# 庄子集解

**Collected Explanations of *Zhuangzi*
Volume One**

**Inner Chapters**
1. "Free and Easy Wandering" (Xiaoyaoyou)
2. "The Equality of Things" (Qiwulun)
3. "The Secret of Caring for Life" (Yangshengzhu)
4. "The World of Men" (Renjianshi)
5. "The Sign of Virtue Complete" (Dechongfu)
6. "The Great and Venerable Teacher" (Dazongshi)
7. "Fit for Emperors and Kings" (Yingdiwang)

**Volume Two**

**Outer Chapters**
8. "Webbed Toes" (Pianmu)
9. "Horses’ Hooves" (Mati)
10. "Opening the Trunk" (Quqie)
11. "Letting Be and Exercising Forbearance" (Zaiyou)
12. "Heaven and Earth" (Tiandi)
13. "The Dao of Heaven" (Tiandao)
14. "Heaven’s Revolutions" (Tianyun)
15. "Cutting Open the Satchel" (Keyi)
16. "Mending Nature" (Shanxing)
17. "Autumn Floods" (Qiushui)
18. "Perfect Happiness" (Zhile)
19. "Mastering Life" (Dasheng)
20. "The Mountain Tree" (Shanmu)
21. "Master Tian" (Tian Zifang)

**Volume Three**

**Miscellaneous Chapters**
22. "Knowledge Wanders North" (Zhi Beiyou)
23. "Gengsang Chu" (Gengsangchu)
24. "Xu Wugui" (Xu Wugui)
25. "Ze Yang" (Zeyang)
26. "External Things" (Waiwu)
27. "Metaphorical Sayings" (Yuyan)
28. "Yielding the Throne" (Rangwang)
29. "Robber Zhi" (Daozhi)
30. "Discoursing on the Sword" (Shuojian)
31. "The Old Fisherman" (Yufu)
32. "Lie Yukou" (Lie Yukou)
33. "The World" (Tianxia)

**Collected Explanations of *Zhuangzi*, Volume One**
**Inner Chapters: "Free and Easy Wandering" (First)**

"To speak of wandering free and unfettered beyond all things, roaming boundlessly by following Heaven’s course.

In the Northern Darkness there is a fish—*Shiwen* notes: 'An alternate version writes this as *Ming*, referring to the Northern Sea'—whose name is Kun. *Shiyu* explains: 'Kun means fish roe.' Fang Yizhi observes: 'Kun originally denotes small fish, but Zhuangzi uses it as the name of a great fish.' The size of Kun is unknown, spanning who knows how many thousands of *li*. It transforms into a bird, whose name is Peng. The back of Peng spans who knows how many thousands of *li*; when it rises in flight, its wings resemble clouds hanging from the sky. This bird, when the sea surges, will migrate to the Southern Darkness. *Yupian* states: 'Yun means to move.' Note: It moves upon the sea, hence 'sea surge.' Below, it speaks of 'striking the water,' which confirms this. The Southern Darkness is the Celestial Pond. Cheng Xuanying explains: 'The great sea and vast rivers originate from the workings of creation, not from human effort, hence it is called the Celestial Pond.' Note: This describes the vastness of things, roaming by following Heaven’s course.

*Qi Xie* is a record of marvels. Sima Biao says: '*Qi Xie* is the name of a person.' Emperor Jianwen says: 'It is the title of a book.' It states: 'When Peng migrates to the Southern Darkness, it strikes the water for three thousand *li*—Cui Zhuan notes: 'As it prepares to fly, it beats its wings against the water in a staggering motion'—and spirals upward on the whirlwind for ninety thousand *li*—Cui explains: 'It flaps its wings and circles upward.' *Erya* states: 'A whirlwind is called a *biao*.' Guo Pu’s commentary: 'A violent wind rising from below.' It departs with the winds of the sixth month.' Cheng explains: 'The sixth month marks half a year; it rests upon reaching the Celestial Pond.' This cites *Qi Xie* as evidence.

The *yema* is—as Sima explains—'the wandering *qi* of marshes in springtime.' Cheng explains: 'In the season of verdant spring, when yang *qi* stirs, gazing afar at the marshes, one sees something like galloping horses, hence it is called *yema*.' Dust and motes—as Cheng explains—'Raised earth is called dust; the finest dust is called motes.' These are the living things blown about by each other’s breath. Cheng explains: 'Between Heaven and Earth, the breath of living things stirs one another.' Note: The *Hanshu* commentary on Yang Xiong’s biography states: '*Xi* refers to the breath of exhalation and inhalation.' This describes the minuteness of things, which also roam by following Heaven’s course. This meaning is introduced here to show that things, whether great or small, all move by following Heaven’s course. The text does not elaborate further under 'Peng,' but makes this point here.

Is the deep blue of Heaven its true color? Or is it so distant that it has no end? From Peng’s perspective looking down, it would appear the same as when humans look up at Heaven. This—referring to Peng—is roughly the case, hence 'it is thus.' *Shi* refers to Peng; *shi* refers to humans gazing at Heaven. The bird is ninety thousand *li* above, and the numbers are approximate, hence 'it is thus,' not meaning it comes to a halt. By comparing humans gazing at Heaven to Peng gazing below, it illustrates the extreme height of its ascent.

Moreover, if the accumulation of water is not deep, it has no strength to bear a great boat. Pour a cup of water into a hollow in the floor—Zhi Dun explains: 'This refers to a depression in the shape of a mound in the hall'—and a mustard stalk may serve as a boat—Li Yi explains: 'Jie is a small grass'—but place a cup there and it will stick fast—Cui explains: 'It touches the ground'—for the water is shallow and the boat too large. If the accumulation of wind is not deep, it has no strength to bear great wings. Thus, at ninety thousand *li*, the wind lies beneath it, and only then can it ride the wind. Wang Niansun states: '*Pei* means to rely upon. The *Zhouli* commentary on the *Fengxiangshi* states: '*Feng* means to mount.' Peng is above the wind, hence it speaks of relying upon it. *Pei* and *feng* are similar in sound and meaning. The *Hanshu* biography of Zhou notes that he was enfeoffed as the Marquis of Kuai City, and Yan Shigu’s commentary states: 'Lu Ji pronounces *kuai* as *pei*; the *Chu Han Chunqiu* writes it as the Marquis of Feng City.' This is evidence of the closeness in sound between *pei* and *feng*.' With the blue sky on its back and nothing to hinder or block its way—Sima explains: '*Yao* means to break; *e* means to obstruct. There is nothing to break or obstruct its progress.'"

And only then does it now set its course for the south. Planning to journey southward. The water is likened to the wind—only with ample strength can it bear and carry, making clear that things cannot soar without being propelled by *qi*. The cicada and the fledgling dove laugh at it, saying: "We dart forth in flight, plunging into the elm and sandalwood—sometimes we fall short and simply land upon the earth. Why must one go ninety thousand *li* to the south?" By borrowing the cicada and dove’s mockery, the passage portrays Hui Shi’s likeness. One who travels to the nearby wilderness returns after three meals, with a belly still full; one bound for a hundred *li* lodges overnight to pound grain for provisions; one journeying a thousand *li* gathers three months’ worth of food. These two creatures—referring to the cicada and the dove—what do they know? The analogy is framed for their sake. Lesser understanding does not reach greater understanding; a short lifespan does not compare to a long one. The preceding statement is explicit, while the paired analogies obscure its intent. How does one know this to be so?

The morning mushroom knows not dusk nor dawn; as the *Liezi*’s "Tang’s Questions" states: "Upon rotting soil grow fungi that live at dawn and die at dusk." Dusk refers to night. The cicada knows not spring nor autumn—these are the short-lived. As the commentary notes: "The *hui* cicada, also called the cold cicada or *dili*, is born in spring and dies in summer, or born in summer and dies in autumn." South of Chu lies the *Mingling*, for which five hundred years make a spring, five hundred years an autumn. In high antiquity grew the great *chun* tree, for which eight thousand years make a spring, eight thousand years an autumn. The passage from "South of Chu" onward is quoted in full from the *Liezi*’s "Tang’s Questions," where "Chu" appears as "Jing." Yet Peng Zu alone is now renowned for his longevity, as Li notes: "Peng Zu, named Keng, was a minister of Yao, enfeoffed at Pengcheng. He lived through Yu and Xia into the Shang, reaching seven hundred years, and thus became known for his long life." That the multitude compares itself to him—is this not lamentable? This section expands upon the "short-lived" passage.

What Tang asked Ji is likewise so. In the "Tang’s Questions," "King Tang of Yin questioned Xia Ji," where Zhang Zhan annotates: "A minister of Tang." *Ji* and *ge* are phonetically interchangeable. In the far north, beyond the uninhabited wilds, lies the Dark Sea—a celestial lake. There dwells a fish, its breadth spanning thousands of *li*, its length unknown, called the *kun*. There soars a bird, called the *peng*, its back like Mount Tai, its wings like clouds trailing from heaven. The "Tang’s Questions" states: "At the northernmost reaches, beyond the farthest wilds, lies the Dark Sea—a celestial lake. There dwells a fish, its breadth spanning thousands of *li*, its length commensurate, called the *kun*. There soars a bird, called the *peng*, its wings like clouds trailing from heaven, its body proportionate." Note that the *Liezi* does not speak of the *kun* transforming into the *peng*. From this point to "and where does it intend to go?" the *Liezi* omits these lines, though they appear to follow seamlessly in meaning. The Master of the Lacquer Garden cites antiquity with deliberate ambiguity, exemplifying what is meant by "flowing boundlessly at will to suit oneself."

It mounts the whirling winds, rising ninety thousand *li*, spiraling upward like a ram’s horn. It pierces the clouds, bears the blue sky upon its back, then sets its course southward, bound for the Southern Dark. This again invokes the "Tang’s Questions" as proof. The *chiyan* scoffs at it, saying—as Sima notes: "*Chi* refers to a small marsh; *yan*, the *yan* sparrow. *Chi* was originally written as *chi*." The characters are interchangeable. Xiahou Zhan’s *Refuting Doubts* states: "The *chiyan* cannot soar above the mulberry and elm." The *Wen Xuan*’s commentary on the "Seven Stimuli" notes: "The *yan* sparrow flies no higher than a foot, illustrating its feebleness." Yet how far does a sparrow truly fly? The text below clearly states "several *ren*." "Where does it intend to go? I leap and flutter upward, no higher than a few *ren*, circling among the mugwort and wormwood—this is the utmost of flight. And where does it intend to go?" Again, the *chiyan*’s mockery serves as Hui Shi’s likeness. This is the distinction between small and great, now made explicit.

Thus, those whose knowledge suffices for one office, whose conduct matches one village—Li [Yun] says: "*Bi* means to accord"—whose virtue aligns with one lord and whose *power* validates one state—Guo Qingfan says: "*Er* is read as *neng* [ability]. *Neng* and *er* are interchangeable in ancient script. Office, village, lord, and state form a parallel; knowledge, conduct, virtue, and ability likewise correspond." Sima [Biao] says: "*Zheng* means trust." Yet their self-regard is no different from this. This describes the *chiyan*. Now turning to men, it subtly refers to those like Hui Shi. Yet Song Rongzi still laughs at them. Sima [Biao] and Li [Yun] say: "Rongzi was a man of Song." Cui [Yuan] says: "A worthy." That is, he still finds them laughable. Moreover, though the whole world praises them, they are not spurred on; though the whole world blames them, they are not disheartened—Guo Xiang says: "They are certain of their own attainment." They determine the division between inner and outer—Guo [Xiang] says: "Inner as self, outer as things." They distinguish the bounds of honor and disgrace—Guo [Xiang] says: "Honor for oneself, disgrace for others." This is all. Cheng [Xuanying] says: "Rongzi’s wisdom and virtue extend no further than this." As for his relation to the world, he is not *shu shu ran*. That is, such a one is not often seen.

Though this is so, he has still not established himself. Sima [Biao] says: "Established, meaning to stand firm. His utmost virtue is not yet established." Note: This is to say that Song Rongzi is not yet worthy of admiration. As for Liezi, who rides the wind in his travels—Cheng [Xuanying] says: "Lie Yukou was a man of Zheng, a contemporary of Duke Xū of Zheng." Note: The *Huangdi* chapter of the *Liezi* states: "Liezi studied under Master Lao Shang and befriended Bo Gaozi, mastering the Way of both. He rode the wind and returned." Further below, it says: "He drifted with the wind east and west, like a withered leaf or a dry husk, never knowing whether the wind bore him or he bore the wind." His skill is effortlessly refined—Guo [Xiang] says: "Lingran describes a light and wondrous appearance." After fifteen days he returns. Yet in attaining such fortune, he is not *shu shu ran*. Cheng [Xuanying] says: "Attaining means obtaining. This is the fortune of one who rides the wind as an immortal." Note: This is to say that those who attain such fortune are also not often seen. Though he is spared from walking, he still relies on something. He may avoid traveling on foot, but he must still depend on the wind. Liezi, too, is not yet worthy of admiration.

As for one who rides the constancy of Heaven and Earth and harnesses the transformations of the six qi—Sima [Biao] says: "The six qi are yin, yang, wind, rain, darkness, and light." Guo Qingfan says: "Bian should be read as *change*, forming a pair with *constancy*. Bian and *change* were interchangeable in ancient script."—and thereby roams the boundless, what does he have to depend on? To roam the boundless without reliance is the essential principle of the *Free and Easy Wandering*. Thus it is said: The perfected person has no self, the spirit-like person has no merit, the sage has no name. *Shiwen* notes: "Ji is pronounced as in *record*." Cheng [Xuanying] says: "Perfected describes their essence, spirit-like their function, sage their name, but in reality, they are one." Note: They establish no merit or fame and do not set themselves apart, hence they stand alone.

This is Zhuangzi’s own exposition. Below, he lists four examples to clarify it.

Yao offered the world to Xu You—Sima [Biao] says: "A man of Yangcheng in Yingchuan."—saying: "The sun and moon have risen, yet the torchfire still burns—how difficult it would be to match their light! The timely rain has fallen, yet you still irrigate—how laborious it would be to match its moisture!"

"Sir, you stand and the world is ordered—yet I still occupy your place." *Cheng* notes: "尸 means 'to preside.'" "I see myself as lacking and unworthy; let me yield the world to you."

Xu You replied: "You govern the world, and the world is already well-governed. If I were to replace you, would I do so for the sake of fame? Fame is but the guest of reality—would I become a mere guest? The wren nests in the deep forest, yet needs no more than a single branch." *Li* notes: "鹪鹩 is a small bird." *Guo Pu* says: "The peach-sparrow." "The mole drinks from the river, yet takes no more than a full belly." *Li Yi* says: "偃鼠 is the shrew-mouse." *Li Zhen* says: "偃 may be written as 鼹, a common variant." *Materia Medica* in *Tao Hongjing’s* annotation: "Also called 鼢鼠, it burrows through plowed fields; dig and you will find it." *Shuowen* under 鼢: "A subterranean mouse, transformed from the shrike." *Li’s* account is mistaken. "Return and rest, my lord!"

I have no use for ruling the world. Though the cook neglects his kitchen, the invocator does not leap over the sacrificial vessels to take his place." *Shiwen* notes: "祝 refers to one who conveys the words of spirits." *Case*: This cites Xu You’s refusal of the world as a self-portrait—only thus can one preserve one’s Heaven-given nature intact.

Jian Wu asked Lian Shu (both ancient holders of the Dao), saying: "I heard words from Jie Yu." *Shiwen*: "Huangfu Mi says: 'Jie Yu tilled his own fields. The King of Chu sent envoys with a hundred *yi* of gold and two teams of four horses to summon him, but he did not respond.'" "They were vast yet without foundation," *Shiwen*: "丁浪 *fan*." *Case*: "当 means base." "and went forth never to return. I was alarmed by his words—they were like the Milky Way, boundless and without end." *Cheng* says: "Like the celestial river, distant and lofty; trace its source or flow, and it remains utterly without limit." "They were wildly divergent," *Xuan Ying* says: "径 is the path outside the gate; 庭, the ground outside the hall. 大有 means 'vastly distant.'" "and far removed from human sentiment."

Lian Shu said: "What did his words say?"
Jian Wu replied: "On distant Mount Gushe, there dwells a spirit-person. Their skin is like ice and snow, their grace like that of a maiden." *Li* says: "淖约 means 'beautiful.'" *Shiwen*: "处子 means a maiden in her chamber." "They eat no grains, but drink the wind and sip the dew. They ride the clouds and qi, mount flying dragons, and wander beyond the four seas." *Note*: These three lines also appear in the *Qiwulun* chapter, where "御飞龙" reads "骑日月." "Their spirit is concentrated—these three words are crucial. Only one who roams beyond things can concentrate their spirit. They cause things to suffer no blight, and the harvests to ripen." *Sima* says: "疵 means harm." 疠 is pronounced *lai*, meaning malignant disease. *Liezi’s* *Huangdi* chapter: "Mount Gushe lies in the sea. Upon it dwells a spirit-person who drinks the wind and sips the dew, eats no grains, whose heart is like a deep spring, whose form is like a maiden’s. They bestow no favors, yet things are naturally sufficient; they hoard nothing, yet they lack for nothing. Yin and yang are ever in harmony, sun and moon ever bright, the four seasons ever regular, wind and rain ever balanced, nurturance ever timely, and the harvests ever abundant. The land suffers no pestilence, people no premature death, and things no blight." The Garden of Lacquer Trees takes this as its source.

"I find them mad and unbelievable."
*Kuang*: *Li* reads it as 九况 *fan*. *Case*: The pronunciation is like 诳, meaning "to deceive."
Lian Shu said: "Indeed. The blind have nothing to share in the spectacle of patterns, nor the deaf in the sound of bells and drums. Is it only the body that knows deafness and blindness? The mind has them too. His words are like a maiden’s." *Sima* says: "Like a virgin." *Case*: 时 means "is." The meaning is: his words are like those of a maiden. The following—*this person, this virtue*—are terms of the utmost comparison. "This person, this virtue, will pervade the ten thousand things, uniting them as one, and the world will seek order—who would exhaust themselves over the world’s affairs!" *Li* says: "磅礡 means 'vast and pervasive.'" *Li Zhen* says: "Also written 旁魄, meaning 'widely covering.' Their virtue broadly pervades the ten thousand things, seeking order for the age—how could they trouble themselves with the world’s affairs! As Laozi says: 'I do nothing, and the people transform themselves.'" 乱 means "to order." *Jianwen* says: "弊弊 means 'to manage busily.'" *Case*: 蕲 is the same as 期.

"This person—nothing harms them. Though the great flood reaches the heavens, they do not drown." *Sima* says: "稽 means 'to reach.'" "Though great drought melts metal and stone and scorches mountains and earth, they do not grow hot. Their dust and chaff," *Shuowen* writes 秕 as "". *Shiwen*: "秕糠 means 'trifling and fragmented.'" *Case*: They regard even the most trivial matters as mere dust. "would mold and cast even Yao and Shun—who would trouble themselves with things!

This again cites the spirit-person who takes no heed of the world, to illustrate the way of preserving one’s nature intact. The people of Song carried *zhangfu* hats to Yue," *Li* says: "资 means 'to sell.' *Zhangfu* are Yin dynasty caps, sold as goods." *Sima* says: "诸 means 'to.'" "but the Yue people, with their cropped hair and tattooed bodies, had no use for them." This is a metaphor for having no use for the world.

When Yao governed the people of the world and brought order to the realm’s affairs, he went to see the Four Masters on distant Mount Gushe," *Sima* and *Li* say: "The Four Masters are Wang Ni, Nie Que, Pi Yi, and Xu You." *Li Zhen* says: "The Four Masters are not real people; to seek names to substantiate them is forced." "by the northern bank of the Fen River. There, he lost all sense of his possession of the world." The northern bank of the Fen River was Yao’s capital. *Xuan* says: "窅然 means 'deep and distant.'" *Case*: This means Yao himself forgot his own lordship of the world—what follows is even less worth mentioning.

Huizi said to Zhuangzi," *Sima* says: "His surname was Hui, given name Shi; he served as Prime Minister of Liang." "The King of Wei gave me seeds of a great gourd. I planted them, and they grew to hold five *shi*. When I used it to hold water, its strength could not support itself."

"Cheng says: '树 means 'to plant.' 实 refers to the fruit. Being hollow and brittle, it could not support its own weight.'" When split to make ladles, it was too vast and hollow to hold anything. *Jianwen* says: "瓠落 means 'spacious and empty.'" Cheng says: "Its flat shallowness could not contain much." It was not that it was not *xiao*-like in its greatness—*Shiwen* notes: "呺, also written as . Li says: 'It means 'hollow and large in appearance.''" Yu Yue says: "呺 is a vulgar character; it should be 枵, meaning 'hollow.'"—but I smashed it for its uselessness."

Zhuangzi said: "You, sir, are truly inept at putting great things to use. Among the people of Song, there was one skilled in making an ointment to prevent chapped hands." *Xiang Xiu* says: "龟 means 'cracked and split.'" *Shiwen* notes: "Xu pronounces it as *julun*." *Li Zhen* says: "Here, 龟 is used as a loan for 皲. *Xuanying’s Sound and Meaning* cites *Tongsuwen*: 'Cracking of hands and feet is called 皲; classical texts sometimes write it as 龟坼.' It cites this passage as evidence." For generations, his family had made their living by washing silk floss."

The *Cheng* commentary states: "洴 means 'floating'; 澼, 'drifting'; 絖, 'silk floss.'" Li says: "To drift floss upon the water." Lu Wenchang notes: "洴澼 is the sound of beating floss." A visitor heard of this and offered a hundred *jin* for the recipe. Li explains: "A *jin* is the weight of one cubic *cun* of gold; a hundred *jin* is thus a hundred pounds." The family gathered to deliberate: "For generations, we have made but a few *jin* from beating floss; now, selling this skill in a single morning would bring a hundred *jin*—let us give it to him." The visitor obtained it and presented it to the King of Wu. When Yue posed a threat, the King sent him as a general. In winter, he engaged the Yue forces in a water battle, routed them, and was granted land as a fief. The same ointment that prevents chapped hands brought one man a noble title while another remained a silk-floss beater—such is the difference in how it was used.

Now you possess a gourd of five *shi* capacity—why not consider fashioning it into a great float and drifting upon rivers and lakes? Sima comments: "虑 means 'to bind or fasten.' A *zun* resembles a wine vessel; tied to the body, it allows one to float across rivers and lakes—what is now called a 'waist boat.'" Yet you fret that the gourd is too large to hold anything! Truly, sir, your mind is as tangled as a *peng* plant!" Xiang says: "The *peng* is short and stunted—this describes a narrow-minded scholar." This refers to Hui Shi’s error in deeming the useful as useless, failing to grasp the Way of its application.

Huizi said: "I have a great tree, which people call the *chu*. Its massive trunk is gnarled and unfit for the carpenter’s line, its slender branches twisted and useless for compass or square. Planted by the roadside, no craftsman spares it a glance. Now your words, grand yet impractical, are likewise discarded by all." This is to say, they are cast aside and left untouched.

Zhuangzi said: "Have you alone never seen the wildcat or weasel? As Cheng notes, 'the *sheng* is a wildcat.' Crouching low, it lies in wait for passing prey; as Sima explains, 'wandering creatures, such as fowl and rodents.' Leaping east and west, bounding and darting—heedless of high or low—it falls into the trap’s jaws. The *bi* is the device that ensnares. The *Yantie Lun*’s 'Xingfa' chapter states, 'Traps are set to intercept their paths,' which carries the same meaning. It is also written as *bei*. The *Chu Ci*’s 'Ai Shimming' reads: 'Externally pressed and constrained by the *ji bei*.' The *ji bei* is the same as the *ji bi*. Wang’s commentary in the *Yupian* takes it to mean the body of a crossbow. It dies in the hunter’s net.

Now consider the yak: as Sima explains, 'the *li* ox.' Its greatness is like a cloud hanging in the sky. As Cheng notes, 'Seen from afar in the mountains, it resembles a cloud at the edge of heaven.' This creature can be so vast, yet it cannot catch a mouse. You, sir, possess a great tree and fret over its uselessness—why not plant it in the land of Nothing Whatsoever, the vast and boundless wilds? As Jianwen explains, 'Mo means great.' Wander idly beside it, roam freely in its shade, and lie at ease beneath its boughs. As Guo Qingfan notes, 'According to the *Shuowen*, *xiaoyao* should be written as *xiaoyao*.' Wang Maoye adds: '*Xiaoyao* conveys the sense of harmony, joy, and ease.' It will not be felled by axe or adze, and no thing will harm it. With no use to be made of it—meaning no place where it can be put to use—how could it ever come to grief? As stated in the 'Ren Jian Shi' chapter: 'This is a tree of no talent, with nothing it can be used for.' And again: 'I have long sought what can be of no use.' The 'Shan Mu' chapter likewise says: 'With nothing it can be used for.' The meaning is the same as here."

This further illustrates how the wildcat and weasel meet their doom, how the yak’s greatness is of no avail, and how the *chu* tree, by preserving itself, fares best—all to enlighten Hui Shi. For Hui Shi sought to engage with the world, while Zhuangzi fled it. Hui deemed Zhuangzi’s words useless, unaware that Zhuangzi roamed the boundless, revealing the difference between "great knowledge" and "lesser knowledge."

---

The second chapter of the Inner Chapters, "On the Equality of Things and Opinions," holds that all things and words in the world may be viewed as equal, without need for debate—one need only abide in the Dao. As Su Yu observes: "Nothing in the world is more tangled than the disputes over things. If right and wrong are too sharply defined, the mind becomes burdened. Thus, regard all the world’s words as the spontaneous arising and ceasing of the heavenly pipes, like the natural cries of fledglings—utterly unconnected to oneself. Only then can one forget 'that' and 'this,' merge praise and blame, level noble and base, equalize things and self, cast off the physical form, leave behind life and death, seek the true master, and illumine it with one’s original clarity, letting the mind wander in the infinite." These are the most subtle principles in Zhuangzi’s thought. Yet in his writings, argument abounds and emotion runs high—could he truly have forgotten right and wrong? He merely leaves the principle intact.

Nanguo Ziqi sat leaning against his desk. As Sima explains, "He lived in Nanguo, hence his name." The *Shiwen* notes: "Yin means to lean; Li’s edition has *ji* as 'table.'" As the *Xu Wugui* chapter records, "Guo" appears as "Bo," and "ji" as "table." Gazing at the sky, he sighed, his response seeming as though he had lost his counterpart. As Xiang explains, "Xu means to exhale." The *Shiwen* adds: "Da describes a state of dissolution; the original also has *ta*. Ou is also written as *ou*." Yu comments: "Ou should be read as *yu*, meaning 'to lodge'—that is, what follows refers to 'I have lost my self.'" The *Xu Wugui* chapter omits this line after "sighed." Yan Cheng Ziyou stood in attendance before him. As Li explains, "Ziqi’s disciple, surnamed Yan, named Yan, posthumously titled Cheng, styled Ziyou." The *Xu Wugui* chapter has "Yan Chengzi entered to see him." He asked: "What is this? The *Xu Wugui* chapter reads: 'Sir, you are the most extraordinary of things!' Can the body truly be made like a withered tree, and the heart like dead ashes? The *Wenzi*’s 'Daoyuan' chapter quotes Laozi: 'The form like a withered tree, the heart like dead ashes.' The *Xu Wugui* chapter has the same two lines, with 'tree' as 'frame.' The 'Zhi Beiyou' chapter reads: 'The form like a withered frame, the heart like dead ashes.' The 'Gengsang Chu' chapter also has these two lines, with 'withered frame' as 'withered tree’s branches.' The 'Dasheng' chapter likewise states: 'I grasped his arm, which was like the branch of a withered tree.' Thus, this 'withered tree' is the branch of a withered tree. 'Withered frame' is also a withered branch. The following text differs. The one who now leans against the desk is not the one who leaned against it before." Ziqi said: "Yan, how well you ask! Now I have lost my self—do you understand?"

"Have you heard the human *lai* but not the earthly *lai*? Have you heard the earthly *lai* but not the heavenly *lai*?"

Guo Xiang notes: "*Lai* is a flute."

Ziyou said: "May I ask about its method?"

Cheng Xuanying explains: "*Fang* means technique."

Ziqi said: "The great clod exhales *qi*—Yu Yue comments: '*Kuai* is the body, the great earth.' Cheng Xuanying says: 'Exhaling forth *qi*.'—its name is wind. When it does not act, nothing stirs; when it acts, the myriad hollows roar in fury. Have you alone not heard its *liu-liu* sound?"

*Zhi* here means "its." Below, the same.

The *Shiwen* glosses: "*Liu-liu* is the sound of a long wind. Li Yi’s edition has *liu*."

The towering peaks of mountain forests, the hollows of great trees a hundred spans around—some resemble noses, some mouths, some ears, some crossbeams, some animal pens, some mortars, some depressions, some puddles.

The *Zilin* states: "*Ji* is the square timber atop a pillar." Cheng Xuanying says: "*Quan* is an animal enclosure." Xuan Ying comments: "*Wa* is a deep pool; *wu* is a hollow. Three resemble the body, three resemble objects, and two resemble the earth—all describe the shapes of hollows in wood."

Some rush forth, some whistle, some shout, some inhale, some cry, some wail, some echo, some keen.

Xuan Ying explains: "Rushing is like the sound of water surging; whistling is like the sound of an arrow in flight. Shouting is loud and coarse; inhaling is soft and fine. Crying is high and clear; wailing is low and muddy. Echoing is deep and lingering; keening is clear and ringing—all describe the sounds of the hollows."

The *Shiwen* notes: "*Xiao* is pronounced *xiao*. Sima Biao says: '*Hao* is the sound of weeping.'" A note observes: "In the *Book of Songs*, 'the twittering yellow birds'—the three schools of the *Odes* write this as *yao-yao*."

The first sings *yu*, and the next sings *yong*.

Li Yi says: "*Yu* and *yong* are harmonizing sounds." Cheng Xuanying explains: "Both are the sounds of wind blowing through trees, one following the other."

A gentle breeze brings a small harmony; a whirlwind brings a great harmony.

Li Yi says: "*Ling* is a light wind." The *Erya* states: "A swirling wind is called a *piao*." *He* is pronounced *huo*.

When a fierce wind subsides, the myriad hollows fall silent.

Xiang Xiu comments: "*Li* means fierce; *ji* means to cease."

Have you alone not seen the *tiao-tiao*, the *diao-diao*?"

Guo Xiang says: "*Tiao-tiao* and *diao-diao* both describe swaying."

Ziyou said: "The earthly *lai* is simply the myriad hollows; the human *lai* is the bamboo flutes. Blowing through bamboo pipes in sequence—may I ask about the heavenly *lai*?"

Ziqi said: "Though the blowing of the myriad things differs, it is the wind that makes each cease of its own accord. All take of themselves—who is it that stirs them to fury?"

Xuan Ying comments: "The earthly *lai* waits for the wind to sound; the wind that makes the hollows sound of themselves is the heavenly *lai*." Here ends the quotation from Ziqi.

A note observes: This passage uses blowing as an analogy. The wind blows through the myriad things differently, yet it makes each cease of itself. Moreover, each hollow produces its own sound—thus, the sounding is all self-taken. Yet if the myriad hollows roar in fury, there must be something that stirs them—who, then, is it that stirs them to fury? Realizing who it is, one sees that the sounding of all things cannot take form without dependence—how much more so the distinctions of gain and loss! "Who is it that stirs them?" prompts one to grasp it immediately—this is what is called below the "true ruler."

The great understanding is expansive, the small understanding is expansive—
The great words blaze, the small words prattle.
In sleep, the spirits intermingle; in waking, the forms open—these are the differences of sleep and waking.
In interaction they form bonds, daily contending in the heart.
Some are loose, some are deep, some are dense—these are the differences of engagement.
Small fears make one tremble; great fears leave one dazed—these are the differences of fear and dread.
Their issuance is like the release of a crossbow bolt—this is what it means to govern right and wrong.
Their restraint is like a sworn oath—this is what it means to guard victory.
Their decay is like autumn and winter, speaking of their daily decline.
What they sink into through their doings cannot be made to return.
Their weariness is like a sealed letter, speaking of their aging depths.
The heart near death allows no return to vitality.

Joy and anger, sorrow and delight, thought and sigh, change and dread,
flightiness and dissipation, wantonness and affectation—
in movement and stillness, in interaction, in nature and bearing, all are endowed by Heaven.

Above speaks of humans.
Music emerges from emptiness, soundless yet sounding.
Mushrooms form from steam—
formless yet formed, all are moved by qi.

Above speaks of things.
Day and night alternate before us, yet none know where they sprout.
Day and night succeed one another—from where do they arise?

Enough, enough!
From dawn to dusk, one grasps this—it is that by which life arises!
Since it cannot be traced, it is best to let it be.
Yet in the span of dawn and dusk, one awakens to the true principle.
This is the root of life.
Without that, there is no self;
without the self, nothing can be received.
This is close indeed—
yet one does not know what compels it.

There must be a true ruler, though no trace of it can be found.
One may trust in its form, yet not see its shape;
one may trust in its movement, yet not see the form that moves me.
It has sentience but no form.

The hundred bones,
the nine orifices,
the six viscera—all are contained within.
With whom should I be intimate?
Do you delight in all equally?
Or is there a private preference?
Will you cherish all alike, or favor one among them?
If all are so, are they servants?
Yet servants are not sufficient to govern one another.
Or do they take turns as lord and subject?
There must be a true ruler present.

This is the "true ruler" mentioned above—a phrase that awakens. Whether one grasps its essence or not, it neither adds to nor diminishes its truth. Once receiving its completed form, it does not perish but awaits its end. Buffeted and worn by things, its course rushes on like a galloping steed, yet nothing can halt it—is this not tragic?

Where the true ruler resides, grasping it brings no gain, nor does failing to grasp it bring loss. Yet from the moment one receives form, clinging to it without dying, sitting idle until qi is exhausted, pointlessly contending with external things—watching the years race by like a galloping horse, with no power to stop—is this not sorrowful? Once receiving its completed form, it does not transform but awaits its end. All one’s life toils without seeing success, wearily laboring without knowing where to return—how can this not be pitiful? All that exists is illusory and false, so no success is achieved; exhausted by one’s labors, yet unaware of where to find refuge.

Lu Wenchang notes: "● should be read as *nié*." Sima reads it as *ěr*. Jianwen comments: "Fatigue, the appearance of exhaustion."

If one is called "undying," what benefit is there? The body transforms, and the mind follows suit—can this not be called great sorrow? Though alive, what use is it? When the body changes and the mind changes with it, the spirit is utterly lost. Is human life inherently so deluded? Am I alone in this delusion, or are there others who are not? Once one follows the heart’s inclinations as a teacher, who is without a teacher? Why must one know the principle of alternation for the mind to find its own teacher? Even the foolish possess this. Whatever the heart intends, it follows and fulfills. Taking the heart as a teacher is something everyone has—why must one understand the principle of alternation for the mind to find its own teacher? Even the foolish are not without it.

Before the heart is settled and unified, one arbitrarily judges right and wrong—this is like claiming to have arrived in Yue today when one set out yesterday. Before the heart is firmly established, one rashly forms opinions, declaring this the Way and that not the Way, like boasting of arrival before even setting out. This "right and wrong" is unrelated to the "right and wrong" below. The *Under Heaven* chapter’s phrase "setting out for Yue today and arriving yesterday" is a saying of Hui Shi and the debaters, here used as an analogy.

Thus, one takes what does not exist as existing. Even if the divine Yu were here, he could not comprehend it—what then can I do? Claiming the nonexistent as existent, even the wisdom of Yu could not fathom it. With such self-deception, what can I do?

This passage repeatedly awakens the world.

The word "must" in some editions appears as "if." According to Wang’s note, "if there were a true ruler governing thus," Wang’s edition likely also reads "if."

The word "also" in some editions appears as "perhaps."

"Appearance of exhaustion" in the *Shiwen* appears as "the state of illness and fatigue."

Speech is not like the wind. (This responds to the earlier "wind.")

Those who speak make claims, yet what they claim remains unsettled. Is there truly speech? Or has there never been speech at all? Do they deem it distinct from the chirping of fledglings—does it possess discernment, or does it lack discernment? Human speech is not like the wind’s gusts; the moment one speaks, it is not yet firmly established. Is it truly grounded in speech? Or is there no such speech? Or is it indeed distinct from the cries of newborn birds—or not distinct at all? Even the weight of their words remains undetermined.

How does the Dao become obscured so that truth and falsehood arise? How does speech become obscured so that right and wrong emerge? Obscured means concealed. Why is the Dao concealed such that truth and falsehood appear? Why is speech concealed such that right and wrong arise? Where does the Dao go that it ceases to exist? Why does speech exist yet remain unverifiable?

Xuan comments: "The Dao is present everywhere; it fundamentally requires no speech. Each word and each Dao likewise need no debate."

The Dao is hidden by partial attainment. Partial attainment refers to clinging to what one has achieved as the Dao, unaware of its vastness.

Xuan comments: "Those with biased views thus obscure the Dao."

Cheng cites the *Laozi*: "When the great Dao declines, benevolence and righteousness arise."

Speech is hidden by florid ornamentation.

Cheng comments: "Florid ornamentation refers to the words of empty disputation and embellished rhetoric. Precisely because one is mired in such embellished disputation, the profound words become obscured. As the *Laozi* says: 'Trustworthy words are not beautiful; beautiful words are not trustworthy.'"

Thus arise the right and wrong of the Confucians and Mohists, affirming what they reject and rejecting what they affirm.

Cheng comments: "Long ago, there was a man of Zheng named Huan who studied in the land of the Qiu clan. After three years, his skills were perfected, and he transformed into a Confucian. Confucians follow the examples of Yao and Shun, take the regulations of Wen and Wu as their model, practice the Dao of benevolence and righteousness, and distinguish the ranks of high and low—hence they are called Confucians. Huan’s younger brother was named Di. Huan transformed his brother, and he became a Mohist. Mohists follow the Dao of Yu: esteeming worthiness, revering ritual, practicing frugality and universal love, grinding their heads and treading to the heels to save the common people—this is called Mohism. Though Huan and Di were brothers by blood, each adhered to a different teaching and disputed each other’s right and wrong. Huan resented his brother, was overcome with emotion, and died. Yet the mutual right and wrong between self and other has existed for a long time. The intensity of such contention originated with these two worthies, and thus they are pointed to as the leaders of the disorderly crowd. From this, one sees that when the Dao is lost and speech is obscured, the scrutiny of right and wrong begins."

(Note: The story of the Confucians and Mohists appears in the "Lie Yukou" chapter.)

If one wishes to affirm what they reject and reject what they affirm, then nothing is better than illumination.

Guo Songtao comments: "That and this take the form of opposition, and when right and wrong stand in duality, the right and wrong one upholds are not true right and wrong—the perception of that and this remains."

(Note: "Nothing is better than illumination" means that nothing is better than illuminating it with the inherent clarity of the original state.)

Things are not without that; things are not without this. Where there is opposition, there is both that and this. From that, one does not see; from self-knowledge, one knows. Observing others is obscure; turning inward brings clarity. Thus it is said: That emerges from this, and this likewise depends on that. This is the theory of the mutual arising of that and this. Only after this exists does that come to be, and because of that, this also arises—this is the theory of the initial arising of that and this.

Yet even as it arises, it dies; even as it dies, it arises. Their theories arise and perish, perish and arise, drifting without constancy.

Guo’s interpretation of this as the transformation of life and death is incorrect.

Even as it is permissible, it is impermissible; even as it is impermissible, it is permissible. What is permissible immediately gives rise to what is deemed impermissible; what is impermissible immediately gives rise to what is deemed permissible. Permissible and impermissible are right and wrong. Affirming this and negating that, negating this and affirming that. Where there is affirmation of this, there is also negation of that; where there is negation of this, there is also affirmation of that. Since there is that and this, the arising of right and wrong is endless.

Therefore, the sage does not follow this path.

Xuan comments: "Does not follow the path of right and wrong."

Instead, he illuminates it by means of Heaven.

Cheng comments: "Heaven is nature."

(Note: Illumination means clarity. One simply clarifies it by means of the natural Heaven, without employing right and wrong.)

This too is affirmation of this.

Thus, with right and wrong endless, one does not follow them.

Su Yu comments: "This means 'precisely for this reason.'"

This is also that, and that is also this.

This is the "this."

Guo comments: "This is also made into that by that, and that also deems itself this."

That too has its right and wrong, and this too has its right and wrong.

Cheng comments: "This deems itself right, and that deems itself right; this deems that wrong, and that deems this wrong. Thus, each has its own right, and each has its own wrong."

Is there truly that and this? Or is there truly no that and this?

Divided, there is that and this; united, there is no that and this.

When that and this find no counterpart, this is called the *pivot of the Dao*.

Cheng comments: "Counterpart means opposition. Pivot means essential. Realizing that both that and this are empty, that right and wrong are both illusory, and concentrating the spirit to perceive alone without opposition in the world—this is to grasp the profound essence and attain the pivot of the Dao."

When the pivot first finds the center of the ring, it can respond to the endless.

Guo Songtao comments: "Right and wrong transform in two ways, yet the Dao abides therein; thus, it is called the *pivot of the Dao*. Holding the pivot of the Dao, one roams within the ring. The center is emptiness. Right and wrong revolve endlessly, chasing one another like a ring. Roaming within emptiness, not enslaved by right and wrong, one can then respond to the endless."

The Tang Buddhist Zhanran’s *Fahua Xuanyan Hongjue* quotes an ancient commentary on the *Zhuangzi*: "The inner emptiness of a circular ring has no bounds; thus, it is called the *center of the ring*."

The *Ze Yang* chapter also states: "Ran Xiangshi grasped the center of the ring to follow completion."

This too is an endless, and not-this too is an endless.

Guo comments: "The world deems itself right and others wrong; thus, one right and one wrong proceed endlessly."

Therefore, it is said: "Nothing compares to illumination."

Only the illumination of fundamental clarity can respond to the endless.

This means that where there is that and this, right and wrong arise, and without illumination, the Dao cannot be perceived.

Using a finger to illustrate that a finger is not a finger is not as good as using a non-finger to illustrate that a finger is not a finger.

Using a horse to illustrate that a horse is not a horse is not as good as using a non-horse to illustrate that a horse is not a horse.

Heaven and earth are one finger; the myriad things are one horse.

This sets a shadow for the following statement: "Things are called so and thus become so."

Taken closely from the body, a finger is a finger; taken broadly from things, a horse is a horse.

Now, to say a finger is not a finger and a horse is not a horse, people will surely not believe it. Using a finger or a horse to illustrate this cannot make it clear. But using a non-finger or a non-horse to illustrate it, one can then realize that a finger is not a finger and a horse is not a horse.

Thus, though heaven and earth are vast, they are but one finger; though the myriad things are manifold, they are but one horse.

What is permissible is permissible, and what is impermissible is impermissible.

Guo comments: "What is permissible to oneself is called permissible; what is impermissible to oneself is called impermissible."

The Dao is walked upon and thus formed.

Xuan comments: "Dao means path."

(Note: "Walked upon and thus formed" refers to what Mencius called "using it and thus forming a path." This serves as a metaphor for the following statement and does not pertain to the Dao of principle.)

Things are called so and thus become so.

All things are named as they are designated; such names are not inherently fixed.

Thus, a finger or a horse may be called a non-finger or a non-horse, and a non-finger or a non-horse may also be called a finger or a horse.

How is this so? It is so because it is so.

How is it not so? It is not so because it is not so.

Why is it called so? Where there is "so," all follow and become so.
Why is it called not so? Where there is "not so," all follow and become not so—this is to let others determine right and wrong.

Things inherently have what is so; things inherently have what is permissible.
Nothing is not so; nothing is not permissible.

In the beginning of discussing things, there are inherently "so" and "permissible," as in a finger being a finger and a horse being a horse.
Yet in the later arising of discussions, many names become incorrect. If names are altered, then nothing is not so, and nothing is not permissible—why not let people call them as they will?

The passage from "How is it so?" onward also appears in the "Parables" chapter. Here, "right and wrong" and "permissible and impermissible" are discussed together. Comparing it with the "Parables" chapter, after "it is not so because it is not so," there should be four more lines—"How is it permissible? It is permissible because it is permissible. How is it impermissible? It is impermissible because it is impermissible"—but the present text omits them.

Thus, for this reason, examples are given: a stalk and a pillar, a leper and Xi Shi, the vast and the cunning, the strange and the monstrous—all are united in the Dao.
*Annotation:* "For this reason" (為是) is read as *wèi shì*. *Guo Xiang* comments: "For this reason, a few examples are briefly cited." *Yu Yue* comments: "*Shuowen* states, 'A *tíng* is a stem.' The *Han shu* and *Dongfang Shuo zhuan* read, 'striking a bell with a stalk.' *Sima Biao* comments, 'An *yíng* is a house pillar. A *lì* is a leper.' The stalk and pillar illustrate size; the leper and Xi Shi illustrate beauty and ugliness." *Guo Xiang* comments: "*Huī* refers to vastness; *jué* to the strange and cunning. *Jué* also means deceit; *guài* refers to the monstrous and bizarre."

From the perspective of one who knows the Dao, all can be united as one without differentiation.
Their division is their formation; dividing one thing forms many things.
Their formation is their destruction.
*Guo Xiang* comments: "What is formation here is destruction there, as in weaving scattered fur or building a house by felling trees."

All things have no inherent formation or destruction, but are again united as one.
Since formation is destruction and destruction is formation, regardless of formation or destruction, all can again be united as one without differentiation.

Only the enlightened know how to unite them as one, and for this reason, they do not employ their own views but lodge them in the ordinary.
Only those who comprehend the Dao can view them as one; for this reason, they do not impose their own perspectives but rest them in common principles.

The ordinary is the function;
*Xuan Ying* comments: "The function of what is seemingly without use."
The function is the connection;
The function of what seems without use lies in perceiving its connection.
The connection is attainment.
Perceiving the connection leads to natural attainment.
Attainment is the subtle approach.
Natural attainment is the subtle approach to the Dao.
This is the way of it.
This is called "following" (*yin*).
*Following* means yielding—yielding to Heaven’s way.

Once this is so, yet one does not know why it is so, this is called the Dao.
*Xuan Ying* comments: "Once united as one, one does not know why it is so—this is without deliberate intent. This is what is meant by 'natural attainment is the subtle approach.'"

Yet if one exhausts their spirit and clarity to seek unity while failing to recognize their inherent sameness, this is called "morning threes."
If one labors their spirit and clarity to seek unity yet does not recognize their fundamental sameness, they are confined to a single corner of the present, no different from the "morning threes" parable.

What is "morning threes"?
A monkey keeper distributed chestnuts, saying, "Three in the morning and four in the evening." The monkeys were all enraged.
He then said, "Then four in the morning and three in the evening." The monkeys were all pleased.
Though names and realities suffered no loss, joy and anger were manipulated—this, too, is "following."

The *Liezi* "Emperor Huang" chapter states: "In Song there was a monkey keeper who loved monkeys and raised them in troops. He understood the monkeys' minds, and the monkeys grasped his intentions. He reduced his family's provisions to satisfy the monkeys' desires. Soon, he grew impoverished and intended to limit their food. Fearing the monkeys would not submit, he first deceived them, saying, 'I will give you chestnuts—three in the morning and four in the evening. Is that enough?' The monkeys all rose in anger. After a moment, he said, 'Then four in the morning and three in the evening. Is that enough?' The monkeys all prostrated themselves in delight. Things that use their cunning to deceive one another are all like this. The sage uses wisdom to ensnare the foolish, just as the monkey keeper used wisdom to ensnare the monkeys. Though names and realities suffered no loss, he manipulated their joy and anger!"

*Zhang Zhan*’s commentary: "One who enjoys raising gibbons is called a monkey keeper. *Xu* (chestnut) is pronounced *xù*." *Zhuangzi* cites this to show that though names and realities suffer no loss, joy and anger are manipulated—this is merely following their nature, which is also the meaning of "yielding."

Thus the sage harmonizes them with right and wrong and rests in the celestial balance—this is called "walking the two paths."

*Shiwen* notes: "Balance (*jun*) is also written as *均*."
*Cheng* comments: "Balance refers to the principle of natural equilibrium."
*Case*: This means the sage harmonizes right and wrong, resting together in the realm of natural equilibrium, where things and self each attain their place—this is "walking the two paths."
*Case* in the "Parables" chapter also states: "Beginning and end are like a ring, with no discernible order—this is called the celestial balance. The celestial balance is the celestial boundary." Here it is written as *钧* (*jun*), a common loan character.

The knowledge of the ancients reached its utmost.
*Cheng* comments: "Utmost is the name for the highest attainment."
How far did it reach? Some held that things had never yet begun to exist—this was the utmost, the exhaustion of all possibility, beyond which nothing could be added.
*Guo* comments: "This is to forget heaven and earth, to leave behind the myriad things, to look outward without examining the cosmos, to look inward without perceiving one’s own body. Thus one is vast and unburdened, moving with things and responding to all without obstruction."
Next were those who held that things existed but had never yet been bounded.
Boundaries (*feng*) are limits. This next level saw things as existing but still without self and other.
Next were those who held that boundaries existed but right and wrong had never yet arisen.
Though they saw self and other, they still lacked right and wrong.
The manifestation of right and wrong is the injury of the Dao.
When right and wrong appear, the undivided wholeness of the Dao is harmed.
The injury of the Dao is the completion of partiality.
Partial love is completed through right and wrong.
Is there truly completion and injury? Or is there truly no completion and injury?
*Cheng* comments: "Truly (*guo*) means decisively. The Dao is without increase or decrease; things have injury and completion. Thus when partiality is completed, the Dao is said to be injured, yet the Dao is truly without injury. This hypothetical argument clarifies the meaning."
Where there is completion and injury, there is Master Zhao’s zither-playing.
Where there is no completion and injury, there is Master Zhao’s not playing the zither.
*Xuan* comments: "Thus (*gu*) means ancient."
*Cheng* comments: "His surname was Zhao, his given name Wen—an ancient master of the zither. If he played the *shang* note, the *jiao* was lost; if he struck the *gong*, the *zhi* was missing. Better to set it aside and not play, so the five notes remain whole. This is like how preserving emotions disrupts the Dao, while forgetting knowledge aligns with reality."
Master Zhao’s zither-playing, Master Kuang’s propping up his baton,
*Cheng* comments: "Propping (*zhi*) means resting. The baton (*ce*) is the stick used to beat the drum; it also refers to the stick used to keep time."
*Case*: Propping up his baton means leaning on it without striking.
and Master Hui’s leaning on the *wu*-tree—
*Sima* comments: "*Wu* is the zither."
*Cheng* comments: "Checking the records, there is no mention of Master Hui being skilled at the zither. Leaning on the *wu*-tree simply means resting against a *wu*-wood table while discoursing."
*Case*: Following *Cheng*’s interpretation. In the "Signs of Full Virtue" chapter, Zhuangzi says to Master Hui: "Now you strain your spirit outward and exhaust your essence, leaning against a tree to chant, resting your withered *wu*-wood table to doze." Resting against the *wu*-table to doze—this is when the skilled debater does not debate, when the one who props up his baton does not strike. The above reference to Master Zhao playing the zither also carries the implication of not playing.
These three men’s knowledge nearly reached perfection!
All were at their peak, so they are recorded at the end of their lives.
*Cui* comments: "Recorded in the present."
*Case*: Master Zhao was skilled at the zither, Kuang understood music, and Hui discoursed on names and principles—these three men’s knowledge nearly reached perfection! All were at their most flourishing, so they are recorded and passed down to later generations.
Only because they prized it did they differ from others; because they prized it, they wished to make it clear to others.
*Xuan* comments: "Only because they considered themselves different from others did they wish to enlighten others."
*Cheng* comments: "Others refers to the common people."
*Case*: The four phrases "Only because they prized it" refer specifically to the skilled debater.
What they sought to clarify was not meant to be clarified, so they ended in the obscurity of "hard and white."
What was not necessarily to be made clear, they insisted on making clear to all—like the debates over "hard stone" and "white horse," which they wanted everyone to understand, yet which ended in obscurity. Thus it is said, "they ended in the obscurity of 'hard and white.'" "Hard and white" also appears in the "Signs of Full Virtue," "Under Heaven," "Heaven and Earth," and "Autumn Floods" chapters.
*Cheng* comments: "Gongsun Long was a man of Zhao. During the Warring States period, his disciples like Kong Chuan stubbornly upheld this doctrine, parading it across the world, overpowering people’s mouths but not their hearts."
And his son, in turn, ended his life spinning Zhao’s threads, achieving nothing in his lifetime.
*Guo* comments: "Zhao Wen’s son, in turn, spent his life continuing his father’s threads."
*Cheng* comments: "Zhao Wen’s son relied on his father’s legacy, spending his years and life without achievement."
*Case*: Continuing his father’s threads is like the *Doctrine of the Mean*’s "continuing the threads of King Tai, King Ji, and King Wen." What is called "achieving nothing" means no more than mastering a single skill while straying far from the Dao—still, it is achieving nothing. If this can be called achievement, then even I have achieved. If this cannot be called achievement, then neither things nor I have achieved. If this cannot be called achievement, then the world’s unachieved are many. One should know that chasing things with the self is all achieving nothing. Thus the dazzling confusion of doubt is what the sage plans against. For this, he does not employ it but lodges it in the ordinary—this is called "illuminating with clarity.""

"Sima Biao states: '滑疑 means disorder.' *Case*: Though it disorders the Dao, it is sufficient to dazzle the world—hence 'the dazzling confusion of doubt.' The sage must plan to remove it, for it harms the Great Dao. For this, he does not employ his own wisdom but lodges it in the ordinary principles—this is called 'illuminating with the inherent clarity.'

The above speaks of seeking the Dao: no thing can be admitted. To grasp one aspect of things and take it as the Dao cannot be called achievement.

Now, suppose there is a statement here—is it of the same kind as this? Or is it not of the same kind? Whether of the same kind or not, if they are taken as the same kind, then there is no difference from that. If people all cling to their own views, and now there is a statement like this—is it of the same kind as mine? Or is it not of the same kind? If one strives to prevail over the other and draws what is not of the same kind into the same kind, then is there any difference from that which is not of the same kind?

Xuan comments: 'This is me.' Nevertheless, let me try to speak of it.

Cheng comments: '尝 means to attempt.'

There is that which has a beginning; there is that which has not yet begun to have a beginning.

Cheng comments: '未始 means not yet.'

*Case*: The inception of an affair has not yet appeared.

There is that which has not yet begun to have that which has not yet begun to have a beginning. There is neither inception nor affair, only the principle of the affair.

There is that which has being; there is that which has non-being—speaking of being and non-being.

There is that which has not yet begun to have non-being—speech has not yet emerged.

There is that which has not yet begun to have that which has not yet begun to have non-being. Even the mind that would give rise to speech has not yet sprouted.

Suddenly there is being and non-being, yet one does not know whether being or non-being is truly being or non-being. Suddenly there are those who speak of being and those who speak of non-being. Yet those who speak of being may have exhausted their feelings, while those who speak of non-being may not have fully expressed their intent. Thus, what is being may become non-being, and what is non-being may become being—hence 'one does not know whether being or non-being is truly being or non-being.'

Now, I have already made a statement; I have clearly spoken. Yet I do not know whether what I have called it is truly a statement or truly not a statement. I do not know whether what I have spoken is truly speech or truly non-speech. If it accords with the Dao, it is speech; if it does not accord, then speech and non-speech are equal.

In the world, nothing is greater than the tip of an autumn hair, yet Mount Tai is small. Nothing is longer-lived than a child who dies young, yet Pengzu is short-lived.

*Exegesis*: 'A child who dies young' refers to one who dies prematurely. Some say: one who dies under nineteen years of age is called a *shangzi*. Sima Biao states: 'The hair of a rabbit grows in autumn.'

Cheng comments: 'In autumn, animals grow fine hair; its tip is extremely minute, hence the tip of an autumn hair. One who dies in infancy is called a *shangzi*. The arising of things involves different forms and qi—some are small, some are large, some die young, some live long. If one speaks in terms of their inherent nature, none is not self-sufficient. Thus, if self-sufficiency is taken as greatness, nothing in the world is greater than the tip of a hair, and nothing is smaller than Mount Tai. If Mount Tai is small, then nothing in the world is great; if the tip of a hair is great, then nothing in the world is small. Since small and large are thus, so too are short-lived and long-lived. Therefore, though the Two Modes are vast, their self-sufficient natures are equal; though the myriad things are many, their self-attained meanings are one.'

*Case*: This is what Zhuangzi of Qiyuan called 'equalizing Pengzu and the child who dies young.' Yet as previous scholars have said, this is truly empty, absurd fabrication. His intent is that Mount Tai and the tip of a hair are both things within the realm; if there are things a thousand myriad times greater than Mount Tai, then Mount Tai is no more than equal to the tip of a hair—hence 'nothing is greater than the tip of an autumn hair, yet Mount Tai is small.' Pengzu and the child who dies young are both persons within the realm; if Pengzu lived seven or eight hundred years before dying, then Pengzu is no more than equal to the child who dies young—hence 'nothing is longer-lived than a child who dies young, yet Pengzu is short-lived.' If I can be without limit with heaven and earth, then heaven and earth are born together with me; if I need not compete with the myriad things, then the myriad things and I are one."

The arts of the Dao from the Lacquer Garden are exquisitely subtle, awakening the deluded world to directly point to the primal origin and preserve each being’s true nature. Yet vulgar scholars merely seek it in literary compositions, only increasing their delusion.

Once things are already one, can there be speech? What place is there for words? Once things are called one, can there be no speech? To call it one is already speech. One and speech make two; two and one make three. Proceeding from this, even the most skilled mathematician cannot reckon the sum—how much less can ordinary people! Cheng comments: “To speak of one with words is to treat one as something other than words. Once one is one, and words are spoken of it, there arises the duality of one and speech. Then, taking the later duality of names and opposing it to the earlier wondrous oneness, does not one and two together make three? From three onward, even those with the most ingenious reckoning cannot keep count—how much less the common run of people!”

Thus, from non-being to being, one reaches three—how much more from being to being! Cheng comments: “‘From’ means to follow; ‘to’ means to proceed. The ultimate principle is wordless; once words arise, names follow. From wordlessness to words, the moment one speaks, one reaches three. How much more when proceeding from words to words—branches and streams divide endlessly, how can they be exhausted?”

Do not proceed; rest in this. If there is no proceeding, there is only spontaneous compliance. This is why the great and small of things, the long and short lives of people, are all equalized—one attains the subtlety of spontaneous compliance.

The Dao has never had boundaries. Cheng comments: “The Dao is everywhere; what boundaries could it have?” Words have never been constant. Guo comments: “Words of this and that give rise to unfixed right and wrong.” It is for this reason that there are divisions.

Because words are inconstant, boundaries arise. Let me speak of these boundaries: there is left, there is right; some bare the left shoulder, some the right. There are ranks, there are principles. Guo comments: “Every thing has its reason; every affair has its fittingness.” The *Shiwen* notes: “Cui’s edition reads ‘there are discourses, there are discussions.’” Yu comments: “Cui’s edition is correct. The text below says, ‘preserve but do not discourse,’ ‘discourse but do not discuss.’ It also says, ‘Thus, division is where there is no division; distinction is where there is no distinction.’ What it calls division and distinction are precisely the ‘divisions and distinctions’ here. Thus, what it calls discourse and discussion are precisely the ‘discourses and discussions’ here.” Note: The above speaks of “boundaries”; ranks and principles are not boundaries. We should follow Yu’s reading.

There are divisions, there are distinctions—division is differential regard; distinction is analytical separation. There is competition, there is contention—competition is paired rivalry; contention is group strife. These are called the eight virtues. Virtue means attainment. Each clings to what it has attained, and only then are words spoken. These are the eight categories.

Beyond the six directions, the sage preserves but does not discourse. Cheng comments: “The six directions are heaven, earth, and the four quarters. The wondrous principle is subtle and formless, transcending the six directions—thus it is preserved but not discoursed upon.” Within the six directions, the sage discourses but does not discuss. Cheng comments: “Within the six directions refers to the inherent nature bestowed upon living beings. The sage responds to their stimuli and circumstances, expounding as needed. Since it is based on emptiness, there is nothing to discuss in detail.”

The *Spring and Autumn* ordering of the world, the records of the former kings—these the sage discusses but does not dispute. Cheng comments: “The *Spring and Autumn* refers to eras; the former kings are the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors. ‘Records’ means to document. Following the examples of Xuanyuan and Zhuanxu, taking Yao and Shun as models, they recorded eras to serve as canonical instructions. The sage discusses them for the benefit of the time, but ultimately does not take sides in disputes or cling to past traces.” Note: The *Spring and Autumn* ordering of the world refers to using years to organize worldly affairs; it is not the *Spring and Autumn Annals* composed by Confucius.

Thus, division is where there is no division; distinction is where there is no distinction. Taking non-division as division, non-distinction as distinction. One asks: Why? The sage embraces and preserves it in the heart. The multitude distinguishes it to display to one another—boasting and showing off. Thus it is said: Distinction is where there is no seeing. Not seeing the greatness of the Dao, only then do distinctions arise.

The great Dao is not named. Xuan comments: “It cannot be designated.” Great distinction does not speak—letting others awaken on their own, not bending them with words. Great benevolence is not benevolent. Cheng comments: “Nurturing all beings, loving without intent—like the springtime, it is not for the sake of benevolence.” Great integrity is not modest. The *Shiwen* notes: “Xu reads this as ‘humble.’” Cheng comments: “Knowing all realms are illusory, nothing is worth coveting; self and others are both empty—what is there to yield?” Great courage is not harmful. Xuan comments: “Without the impulse to harm others.”

The Dao that is manifest is not the Dao—displaying the Dao to dazzle others must not be the true Dao. Words that distinguish do not reach. Xuan comments: “They cannot prevail in distinction.” Benevolence that is constant does not succeed. Guo comments: “Constant love is necessarily incomplete.” Integrity that is pure is not trusted. Xuan comments: “Outwardly appearing flawless, the center remains unknown.” Courage that harms does not succeed. Cheng comments: “...”

"To abandon compassion and embrace courage is to oppose the natural inclinations of things with hostility—all will despise it, and nothing will be accomplished."

In these five, one nears the square from the round. *Shiwen* notes: “Cui reads this as ‘round’; Sima comments: ‘It means round.’” Cheng comments: “‘Nears’ means approaches.” Xuan comments: “These five are originally a seamless whole, but now, clinging to traces, one approaches the square from the round—this cannot be practiced.”

Thus, to know where to stop in what one does not know is perfection. Cheng comments: “When wisdom does not suffice, do not force knowledge. To know where to stop within one’s bounds is the ultimate attainment of learning.”

Who knows the eloquence of non-speech, the Dao of non-Dao? "Non-Dao" refers to the earlier "not displaying." If one can know this, it is called the Heavenly Treasury. Xuan comments: “Within this undifferentiated whole, nothing is not contained.”

It may be drawn upon without being depleted, poured from without being exhausted, yet one does not know its source. Guo comments: “The coming of the supreme principle is naturally without trace.” This is called "preserving light." Cheng comments: “To preserve is to conceal. Veiled, its light grows ever brighter. To say that the Dao is revealed through words is not the Dao—this is repeatedly made clear.”

---

Formerly, Yao asked Shun: “I wish to attack Zong, Kuai, and Xu’ao.” Cui comments: “Zong is one state, Kuai two, Xu’ao three.” (As noted in the "Among the People" chapter: “Yao attacked Congzhi and Xu’ao, turning their lands into desolate ruins.” This shows he did not follow Shun’s counsel.) Yet when he sat facing south, he could not feel at ease. Cheng comments: “‘At ease’ describes a joyful countenance.” (Note: *shi* here is interchangeable with *yi*. This passage also appears in the "Gengsang Chu" chapter.)

"Why is this so?" Shun replied: "Those three lords still dwell among the brambles and mugwort. *Dwell* means *remain*. (Cheng comments: “Brambles and mugwort are lowly grasses.”) If you do not feel at ease, what is the reason? Formerly, ten suns appeared at once. (As the *Huainanzi* states: “In Yao’s time, ten suns appeared together, and he sent Yi to shoot down nine of them.”) Thus I draw this analogy. All things were illuminated—how much more so the virtue that surpasses the sun!"

(Cheng comments: “*Surpass* means *exceed*. To suppress the desires of those dwelling in brambles and mugwort and force them to submit would hardly accord with the great Dao!") Yao and Shun serve as one proof.

Nie Que asked Wang Ni: (Li’s *Exegesis* reads: “Ni: Xu pronounces it *wǔ-jī*; Li’s *Sound and Meaning* gives another reading. The *Biographies of Eminent Recluses* states: ‘Wang Ni was a worthy man of Yao’s time.’ The ‘Heaven and Earth’ chapter calls him ‘Nie Que’s teacher.’”) "Do you know what things universally affirm?" He replied: "How would I know that?"

(Guo comments: “What is universally affirmed may not be right; what differs is not necessarily wrong. Neither side can correct the other, so there is no use for knowledge.")

"Do you know what you do not know?" (Cheng comments: “Since you do not know what things universally affirm, do you at least know what *you* do not know?”) He replied: "How would I know that?"

(Guo comments: “If you knew what you did not know, that would itself be knowledge—knowledge that would prevent you from letting all things find their own proper place.")

"Then are things without knowledge?" (Since you have no knowledge, are all things likewise without knowledge?) He replied: "How would I know that!"

(Cheng comments: “Not only do I not know myself, I also do not know things. Both self and things are forgotten, so there is nowhere to apply knowledge.")

Yet let me try to speak of it. How do I know that what I call knowing is not not-knowing? How do I know that what I call not-knowing is not knowing?

(Li comments: “*Yong* means *use*; *ju* means *how*.”)

Here the commentator notes: "Minor knowledge still does not constitute true knowing—then not-knowing is not necessarily wrong."

Moreover, let me put this question to you: When people sleep in damp places, they develop lower back pain and partial paralysis—is that so? Yet loaches do not. When monkeys perch in trees, they tremble with fear—is that so? Yet gibbons do not. Which of these three knows the proper place to dwell? People, loaches, and gibbons—who knows what place is truly right?

People eat domesticated animals and grains; deer and elk graze on grass; centipedes relish snakes; owls and crows favor mice. Which of these four knows the proper taste? People, beasts, insects, and birds—who knows what flavor is truly right?

Gibbons mate with *bianju* apes; elk couple with deer and swim with fish. Mao Qiang and Lady Li are considered beauties by humans, yet fish dive deep at the sight of them, birds take flight, and deer bolt away. Which of these four knows the true standard of beauty in the world?

From my perspective, the beginnings of benevolence and righteousness, the paths of right and wrong, are tangled and confused—how could I discern their distinctions?"

Nie Que said: "If you do not know benefit and harm, does the Perfected Person likewise not know benefit and harm?"

Wang Ni replied: "The Perfected Person is divine: so utterly transcendent that great marshes may burn without scorching him, rivers and seas may freeze without chilling him, lightning may shatter mountains and winds shake the ocean without startling him. One such as this rides the clouds and qi, mounts the sun and moon, and roams beyond the four seas. Death and life bring no change to him—how much less the beginnings of benefit and harm!"

Ju Que asked Master Changwu: "I have heard from the Master—" (Master Changwu, so named for dwelling beneath a tall catalpa; some say his given name was Qiu.) (Yu comments: "Ju Que must have been a disciple of Confucius’ later generation. The Master here refers to Confucius. The line below, *'Qiu, how could he know such things?'* uses Confucius’ given name, showing that Ju Que cited Confucius’ words only to refute them. The later passage, *'Qiu and you are both dreaming—I say you are dreaming even of dreaming,'* where 'I' refers to Master Changwu himself, proves that Qiu cannot be Changwu’s name. If Qiu and you are both dreaming, and I am also dreaming, how could Qiu be Changwu’s name?")

"The sage does not engage in affairs; things come of themselves, and principle responds naturally—he does not pursue them. He does not seek profit, nor does he avoid harm. He does not delight in seeking, nor does he follow the Dao. He speaks without speaking, and when questioned, his silence is an answer. He speaks yet wishes to remain wordless, and roams beyond the dust and grime of the world."

The Master (Confucius) considered these careless, unbounded words—(Menglang: "aimless, without direction"; some say "without boundaries"; others, "rough and approximate"; still others, "broad and general.") But I regard them as the unfolding of the wondrous Dao.

"What do you think of this?" Longwuzi replied: "This is what even the Yellow Emperor found perplexing—how could one like Qiu (Confucius) claim to understand it?

Moreover, you have judged too hastily. It is like seeing an egg and demanding a rooster to crow at dawn, or spying a slingshot and expecting a roasted owl. I have spoken to you idly, and you have listened idly—what of it?

Embrace sun and moon, clasp the cosmos to your breast. *Shizi* says: 'The four directions of heaven and earth are called *yu*; past and present are called *zhou*.' *Shuowen* says: '*Zhou* is the utmost reach of boat and carriage.' Let your lips meet in harmony, set aside confusion and obscurity, and serve one another with the reverence of lowly bondsmen. The masses toil endlessly, while the sage appears dull and unknowing. Interweaving ten thousand years into a single seamless whole, myriad things are all thus, each enfolding the other.

How do I know that delight in life is not a delusion? How do I know that aversion to death is not like a youth who has lost his home and does not know where to return?

Lady Li was the daughter of the border warden of Ai. When the state of Jin first took her, she wept until her tears soaked her collar. But once she reached the king’s palace, shared his bed, and feasted on fine meats, she regretted her tears. This too is but an analogy."

How do I know that those who have died do not regret their initial longing for life? In dreams, one who drinks wine may weep at dawn; one who weeps in dreams may hunt at dawn. While dreaming, one does not know it is a dream. Within the dream, one may even divine the dream—only upon waking does one know it was a dream. Yet the difference between waking and dreaming is itself a distinction. Only with the great awakening does one realize this was the great dream. Death is the great awakening, and life is the great dream. But fools believe themselves awake, priding themselves on their knowledge. They claim to know: "This one is a lord, that one a herdsman!" How rigid and shallow! How can one truly say who is noble as a ruler and who is base as a groom? Truly, they are trapped in their own crudeness.
