You and I, Qiu, are both dreaming; when I say you are dreaming, I too am dreaming. Such words are called *diaogui*—"arriving at the strange." When a great sage appears in ten thousand generations, one who understands these words, it is as if meeting them at dawn or dusk. A true interpreter is rare—one in ten thousand generations is like a morning or evening encounter.

If you and I were to debate, and you won while I did not, would you truly be right and I truly wrong? If I won while you did not, would I truly be right and you truly wrong? Or might one of us be right and the other wrong? Or could both be right or both be wrong? You and I cannot know for certain, and others are likewise shrouded in darkness. Whom could we appoint to judge? If we chose one who agrees with you, how could they judge fairly, already aligned with you? If we chose one who agrees with me, how could they judge fairly, already aligned with me? If we chose one who differs from both of us, how could they judge fairly, already differing from us? If we chose one who agrees with both of us, how could they judge fairly, already aligned with us? Thus, neither you nor I nor others can truly know—so must we wait for another?

If I align with them, they do not trust me; if they align with me, they do not submit. Setting up separate standards of right and wrong, both sides remain doubtful. Following others’ judgments, there is no fixed conclusion. Unable to know for certain, what is there to wait for? Such debates are utterly futile.

The mutual dependence of changing voices is like their non-dependence. Right and wrong, affirmation and negation—these lie with the person.

To deem something right or affirm it as so because others do so—this is no different from being self-affirmed and self-so, neither engaging the heart, as the following passage states.

Harmonize with the heavenly boundary, follow the boundless transformations—this is how one exhausts the years. Cheng comments: *"Heavenly" refers to the natural; "boundary" means division; "boundless transformations" are like ceaseless change. "Follow" means to yield. To harmonize with the natural divisions and yield to limitless transformation is to fulfill one’s allotted lifespan.*

What does it mean to "harmonize with the heavenly boundary"? It means: "Is it so or not? Is it thus or not?" If "is" is truly "is," then its difference from "is not" requires no debate. If "thus" is truly "thus," then its difference from "not thus" requires no debate. Cheng comments: *"Right and wrong, thus and not-thus arise from deluded emotions. When examined by reason, all is empty illusion—what is deemed 'right' is in fact 'wrong,' what is deemed 'thus' is in fact 'not thus.' How do we know this? If 'is' were definitively 'is,' then 'is' would differ from 'is not.' If 'thus' were definitively 'thus,' then 'thus' would differ from 'not thus.' Yet what one calls 'is,' another calls 'is not'; what one affirms as 'thus,' another denies. Thus, right and wrong, thus and not-thus, in principle are not distinct—it is only through mutual opposition that deluded distinctions arise, hence no debate is needed."*

Forget years, forget righteousness. Cheng comments: *"Years are what life is endowed with; since all share in life and death, years are forgotten. Righteousness is the judgment of right and wrong; since all is one in right and wrong, righteousness is forgotten."* Flourish in the boundless, thus lodge in the boundless. Cheng comments: *"Flourish means to expand; boundless means limitless. Lodge means to dwell."* When principle expands without end, the mind dwells without end—no need for debate.

Qūquè and Chángwū’s three proofs:
The hill-shadow asked the shadow: *"Formerly you walked, now you stand still; formerly you sat, now you rise—why are you so lacking in constancy?"* The shadow replied: *"Do I depend on something to be thus? Does what I depend on also depend on something to be thus? I am like the snake’s ventral scales or the cicada’s wings—how could I be otherwise?"* Cheng comments: *"If one depended on wings to fly or feet to walk, there are many creatures—why single out the snake’s scales or cicada’s wings for comparison? The snake sheds its old skin, the cicada emerges with new wings, neither knowing why nor discerning how—they simply transform and live, without dependence. Thus, the relationship between form and shadow is no different from scales and wings."* The text asks: *"Do I resemble the snake’s scales or the cicada’s wings?"*—implying that even these have fixed forms, which is not entirely apt. *"How can I know why it is so? How can I know why it is not so?"* Cheng comments: *"Whether dependent or independent, thus or not-thus, the heavenly mechanism unfolds of itself—none knows its master."*

The hill-shadow and shadow’s four proofs:
Once, Zhuāng Zhōu dreamed he was a butterfly, fluttering joyfully as a butterfly, self-content and at ease. Lǐ comments: *"Content means delighted."*

He delighted in fulfilling his will—*yú* sounding like "remain." He knew not that he was Zhou. Suddenly he awoke, and there he was, palpably Zhou. Cheng comments: *"Qúqú* describes the startled manner of awakening." Did Zhou dream he was a butterfly, or did the butterfly dream it was Zhou? Between Zhou and the butterfly, there must be a distinction. This is called the transformation of things.

Though Zhou and the butterfly must have their divisions, in the moment of dreaming and awakening, he knew not the boundary between Zhou and butterfly—calling Zhou the butterfly is possible, calling the butterfly Zhou is also possible. Here, the two become one in transformation. This is the fifth proof, demonstrated through personal experience, reaching the ultimate realm of *equalizing things.*

**Chapter Three: The Secret of Nurturing Life**

To follow affairs without being ensnared by things, to merge with emotions without disturbing one’s nature—this is the guiding principle of Zhuangzi’s art of nurturing life. Our life is bounded, while knowledge is boundless. Life has its limits, but knowledge knows no shore. To pursue the boundless with the bounded is perilous. Guo Xiang comments: *"Dài* means exhaustion." And to persist in being one who "knows" is even more perilous. *Yǐ* means "to stop." Clinging to thoughts after the moment has passed brings even greater exhaustion. This speaks of engaging with things to no benefit for one’s nature and life.

In doing good, do not seek fame; in doing evil, do not court punishment. Wang Fuzhi comments: *"Things like sensual pleasures, which cannot be called good, are thus evil."* This is a superficial interpretation. Follow the *dū* as your constant guide. Li Yi comments: *"Yuán* means to follow; *dū* means the center; *jīng* means constancy."* Li Zhen comments: *"In the human body, only the spine is central; the *dū* meridian runs upward along the spine, hence its meaning as 'center.'" Wang Fuzhi comments: *"The *dū* is the central channel at the back of the body. To 'follow the *dū*' is to let the subtle, refined qi flow along the void, naturally attaining the center."* This is a profound interpretation. Thus, one can preserve the body, perfect life (fulfilling the principle of one’s existence), nourish one’s kin (returning to them what was received from them, the ultimate form of nourishment), and live out one’s allotted years. The years granted by heaven should be allowed to run their course without premature truncation, so that what is finite becomes infinite. This introduces the central theme of the chapter, which is then illustrated through five analogies.

Butcher Ding carved an ox for Lord Wenhui. The *Shiwen* notes: *"Ding was his name. Cui and Sima state: 'Lord Wenhui was King Hui of Liang.'" Cheng comments: *"Xiè* means to butcher."* Where his hand touched, his shoulder leaned, his foot pressed, his knee braced—*yǐ* as Su Yu explains: *"The *Shuowen* says: '*Yǐ* means one foot.' When the knee is raised, the foot stands alone, hence the term."*—the skin and bone parted with a *huò* sound, the blade resounded with a *huō* noise. Sima comments: *"Huò* is the sound of skin and bone separating."* Cui comments: *"Huò* sounds like *huà*; *huō* is close to *huò*, but louder."* Cheng comments: *"The *huò* sound answered in turn, the phoenix knife advanced, and the great dismemberment came with a *huō*."* Every cut struck the right note, harmonizing with the dance of *Sanglin*. Sima comments: *"Sanglin* was the name of a piece of Tang music."* Cui comments: *"It was the name of a dance in Song."* The *Shiwen* adds: *"As in the *Zuozhuan*: 'The dance master marked the rhythm with the *xia* banner.'" It matched the rhythm of *Jingshou*. Xiang and Sima comment: *"Jingshou* was a movement in the *Xianchi* music, the music of Yao."* Xuan comments: *"Huì* means rhythm."* Lord Wenhui exclaimed: *"Ah! How could skill reach such heights?"* Li comments: *"An exclamation of admiration."*

The Cook put down his knife and replied: *"What your servant delights in is the Dao, which surpasses mere skill. When I first began to dismember oxen, all I saw was the whole ox. After three years, I no longer saw the ox as a whole. Now, I encounter it with my spirit rather than observe it with my eyes—my senses halt, yet my spirit moves. As Xiang comments: *'Meeting it in darkness with the principle.'* As Cheng explains: *'The senses are the governing faculties.'* Here, *'senses'* refers specifically to the eyes. While the eyes still perceive its traces, the spirit has already dissected its form. I follow the natural patterns—*as Cheng notes: *'Following the ox’s natural seams.'*—striking the great gaps, as the *Zilin* defines: *'Striking means to strike.'* As Cheng explains: *'The great gaps are the spaces where seams intersect.'* Guo pronounces *xi* as *que*. I guide the blade through the great cavities, as Guo Qingfan observes: *'Kuan* should read *kuan*, meaning 'hollow.' As the *Hanshu* annotation to Sima Qian’s biography states: *'Kuan* means empty.' It refers to the hollows in the joints." I rely on what is inherently so, never touching the network of vessels, flesh-bound nodes, or knotted sinews. As Yu comments: *'Ji* is likely a corruption of *zhi* (branches). *Zhi* refers to branch vessels; *jing* to main vessels. *Zhijing* thus means 'vessels and channels.' The *Suwen*, citing Wang’s commentary on the *Lingshu Jing*, states: *'Main vessels run internally; branches that extend transversely are called collaterals.'* *Zhi* and *zhi* are interchangeable. Where vessels and channels intersect, the blade’s movement would be obstructed—but I follow what is inherently so, so there is no obstruction." As the *Shiwen* notes: *"Ken* refers to flesh adhering to bone. Sima comments: *'Qi* means the knotted places.'" Thus, the branch vessels, main vessels, flesh-bound nodes, and knotted sinews are all places the blade has never reached—let alone the great bones!" *Gu* is pronounced *gu*.

Cui comments: *"Pan* means coiled around bone." A skilled butcher replaces his knife every year—he uses it for cutting. An ordinary butcher replaces his knife every month—he uses it for breaking. Cui explains: *"Zu* means 'the many.'" Yu notes: "This refers to breaking bone, not the knife breaking. The *Zuozhuan* states: *'Do not break the bone.'"*

Now my knife has been in use for nineteen years, having dissected several thousand oxen, yet its blade remains as if newly whetted on the grindstone. Between the joints there is space, and the blade of the knife has no thickness. Entering with what has no thickness into what has space, how vast is the room for the blade to move! Thus, after nineteen years, my knife’s edge is still as if newly whetted.

Yet whenever I come to a complex joint—Guo explains: "Where sinews and bones intertwine densely is called *zu*"—I perceive the difficulty, grow cautious, focus my gaze—Guo explains: "Not letting the eyes wander elsewhere"—slow my movement—Guo explains: "Moving the hand with deliberation"—and apply the knife with utmost precision. At once, with a *huo* sound, the ox falls apart, like earth crumbling to the ground.

I raise the knife and stand, look around in satisfaction, then wipe the blade clean—Guo explains: "*Chouchu* means the ease of a well-fed horse, the self-contentment of one at leisure." (Note: The *Tian Zifang* chapter also states: "Now he hesitates in satisfaction, now he looks around in pride.") I then put the knife carefully away.

Wenhui Lord said: "Excellent! From the words of Cook Ding, I have learned the Way of nurturing life."

Though oxen are many, the blade is not harmed; though things are varied, the mind is not burdened—these are all principles of nurturing life. A single metaphor.

Lord Gongwen Xuan, upon seeing the Right Minister, exclaimed in surprise: "What manner of man is this? Why does he have but one foot? Was this given by Heaven, or is it the work of man?" The commentator Sima explains: "Is this by Heaven's decree, or by human agency?"

The Right Minister replied: "It is Heaven, not man. Heaven's creation of him made him thus singular." Sima comments: "Singular here means one-footed." (Note: This differs from the three amputees in the *Virtue's Full Manifestation* chapter—his deformity is Heaven-sent, theirs the result of human misfortune.) "Man's appearance is bestowed by Heaven. As Guo explains: 'Two feet walk side by side.' Thus we know this is of Heaven, not of man." Though his form is impaired, his spirit remains whole. To know Heaven is to dwell in compliance. A second metaphor.

The marsh pheasant takes ten steps for a single peck, a hundred steps for a single drink, yet it does not seek to be kept in a cage. "Seek" here means "expect." The same usage appears below. Li explains: "A cage is a pen, used to confine birds." Though its spirit may flourish, it is not at ease. "Not at ease" means it does not find contentment. Birds in the marsh are free; in a cage, they are constrained. When men are bound by glory and splendor, they inevitably lose their proper nurturance. A third metaphor.

When Lao Dan died—Sima comments: "Laozi." (Note: Laozi's lifespan is unknown; this is used illustratively.)—Qin Shi went to offer condolences. He cried out three times and departed.

His disciples asked: "Was he not your friend?"
"Yes."
"Then is such a brief mourning fitting?"
"Yes. At first, I thought him a true man—one who does not die. But now I see he was not. When I entered to offer condolences, I saw elders weeping for him as if for their own children, and the young weeping as if for their mothers. In their gathering, there must have been those who spoke without intending to speak, who wept without intending to weep. This is what is meant by 'gaining trust without words, achieving harmony without contrivance.'" "Gathering" refers to social interaction; "speaking" refers to praise. Laozi's sincerity could move others, so my silence has its own explanation. This is fleeing from Heaven and doubling back on one's nature, forgetting what one has received. "Fleeing" here may also be written as "遯." "Nature" refers to what Master Hui calls "nature," as seen in the *Virtue's Full Manifestation* chapter. "Received" means the form one is given. The ancients called this "the punishment of fleeing from Heaven." This phrase also appears in the *Lie Yukou* chapter. In *Virtue's Full Manifestation*, Confucius is described as bearing Heaven's punishment, so "fleeing Heaven's punishment" is a term of praise. Previous interpretations have all been mistaken.

"When he came, it was his time; when he departed, it was his compliance. To rest in one's time and dwell in compliance is to be beyond the reach of sorrow or joy. The ancients called this 'the Great Release.'" "Release" is pronounced *xuan*. Cheng explains: "The Great refers to Heaven." (Note: The *Great Master* chapter states: "Gaining is one's time; losing is one's compliance. To rest in one's time and dwell in compliance is to be beyond the reach of sorrow or joy. This is what the ancients called the Great Release." This passage closely parallels the present text. "Coming and going" and "gaining and losing" both refer to life and death.) Guo's commentary in *Virtue's Full Manifestation* also says: "Life is my time; death is my compliance. Time is my gathering; compliance is my dispersal. Heaven endows man with nature, which is the 'suspension.' To darken one's nature and follow the course is Heaven's Great Release." Since the Master has died, why should I grieve? A fourth metaphor.

"When the finger is exhausted in splitting wood for kindling, the finger may reach its limit, but the kindling will also be exhausted in time. Yet the fire is transmitted, and its end is unknown. Though the form departs, the spirit remains ever-present—this is the ultimate principle of nurturing life. The kindling has its limit, but the fire knows no end." A fifth metaphor.

**Book IV: "Among the World of Men"**
"Among the world of men" refers to the present age. To serve a tyrannical ruler, to dwell in a corrupt world, to engage with others—one must not contend for fame but conceal one’s virtue: this is the way to preserve oneself whole. At the end, it cites the song of Jie Yu: *"The world to come cannot be awaited; the world gone by cannot be pursued."* This is how the Master of the Varnished Garden expressed his lament, naming the chapter *"Among the World of Men."*

Yan Hui sought leave of Confucius to depart. Confucius asked, *"Where are you going?"* Yan Hui replied, *"I am going to Wei."* Confucius asked, *"What will you do there?"* Yan Hui said, *"I have heard that the Lord of Wei—"* (Sima Biao notes: *"The Lord of Wei refers to Duke Zhuang, Kuai Kui."* However, according to the *Zuozhuan*, Duke Zhuang entered the state in the winter of the fifteenth year of Duke Ai of Lu, by which time Yan Hui had already died. This must refer to Duke Chu, his successor.) (Yao Nai comments: *"The Lord of Wei is a pretext, pointing to those rulers of the time who ravaged their people."*) *"—is in his prime, yet his conduct is singular and self-willed. He treats his state lightly and conscripts the people without restraint. Yet he does not perceive his own faults."* (Guo Xiang notes: *"None dare admonish him."*) *"He treats the deaths of his people as lightly as deploying troops. The dead are measured by the state as if filling a marsh—like rushes set aflame, the people of the state perish in such numbers that, if compared to the land, it is as though they were scorched by fire."* (Guo Songtao notes: *"Jiao* 蕉 *is interchangeable with* jiao *焦. The* Zuozhuan *of Duke Cheng’s ninth year writes* jiao cui *蕉萃, while Ban Gu’s* "Rhapsody on the Guest" *writes* jiao cui *焦瘁. The* Guangya *states: ‘*Jiao *means black.’"*) *"The people have nowhere to turn."*

"Formerly, I once heard you say, Master: *'A well-governed state—leave it,'* as Xuān comments, *'Nothing to attend to.'* *'A disordered state—go to it,'* as Xuān comments, *'Desiring to aid its salvation.'* *'The physician’s gate teems with illness.'*—this is a metaphor. Wishing to reflect upon what I have heard and ponder its principles,'* as Cuī and Lǐ comment, *'Principles, that is, models,'* *'might my state yet find recovery?'* as Lǐ comments, *'Recovery means healing.'*

Confucius said, *'Alas! You will likely go only to suffer punishment!'* as Chéng comments, *'You—if you go, you will fear being executed.'* *The Way does not tolerate admixture. Admixture breeds multiplicity; multiplicity breeds disturbance; disturbance breeds anxiety; anxiety admits no salvation.'* as Chéng comments, *'The Way lies in purity. Admixture spawns a tangle of affairs; affairs multiply and the mind becomes disordered; disorder gives rise to anxiety. When remedy and malady are at odds, both parties are confounded. If one cannot even establish oneself, how can one save others?'*

*The perfected of antiquity first established themselves before establishing others,'* as Chéng comments, *'Established means secured.'* *If what is established within oneself remains unsettled, how can one presume to reach the conduct of a violent ruler? Reach here means to attain.'* *Violent ruler refers to the Lord of Wèi.*

*Moreover, do you truly understand whence virtue’s dissipation arises, and from what knowledge emerges? Virtue dissipates through fame; knowledge emerges through contention.'* as Chéng comments, *'Virtue’s dissipation and loss of authenticity stem from pride in fame. Knowledge’s transgressive emergence beyond its proper bounds arises from competition for excellence.'*

*Fame is that by which men mutually injure one another; knowledge is the instrument of contention. Both are instruments of misfortune, not the means to fully enact the Way.'* as Chéng comments, *'Injure means to harm.'* *Note: Both are instruments of calamity, not the means to fully realize the way of the world.* As Sū Yǔ comments, *'To heal a state is a fine reputation; to treat illness is to display much knowledge. Proceeding with such a mindset, the seeds of contention and injury are already sown—hence, instruments of misfortune.'*

*This is speaking superficially; below, it delves deeper. Even without the intent to contend for fame or employ knowledge, merely wielding the language of benevolence, righteousness, plumb-line, and ink-mark to admonish a ruler is still insufficient to traverse a disordered age and escape disaster—how much less so if one carries instruments of misfortune and proceeds!*

*Moreover, though one’s virtue be profound and trust solid as stone, it may not yet penetrate the qi of others; though one’s reputation be uncontentious, it may not yet reach the hearts of others.'* as Jiǎnwén comments, *'Solid as stone describes sincerity and substance.'* *Note: Though one be sincere and profound without employing knowledge, one may not yet win the confidence of others’ vital essence; though one contend not for fame, one may not yet comprehend others’ hearts and minds—people will surely suspect you.*

*Yet to forcibly present the language of benevolence, righteousness, plumb-line, and ink-mark before a violent ruler is to make others resent what is beautiful in you,'* as the *Shìwén* notes, *'Forcibly has two readings.'* *Shù here means to expound.* As Guō Sōngtāo comments, *'In the* Lǐjì’s *"Rhapsody on Sacrifice,"* "and expounding it to examine it," *Zhèng’s commentary reads:* "Expounding should be written as narrating." *Note: If one acts thus, it is as though one possesses beauty oneself, and others will surely resent it.*

*To call this "disastering others." One who disasters others will surely have disaster visited upon them in return. How perilous it would be to become a disaster to others!'* as Chéng comments, *'Call means to name.'* as the *Shìwén* notes, *'Disaster is pronounced zāi.'*

*Moreover, if one delights in worthiness and detests unworthiness, what need is there to seek distinction? You—if the Lord of Wèi truly loved good and hated evil, his court would abound with upright men. What need would there be for you to seek distinction?'*

*If you remain utterly silent, the king and duke will surely exploit your vulnerabilities and contend for victory in eloquence.'* as Chéng comments, *'Admonition means speech. King and duke refers to the Lord of Wèi.'* *They will dazzle your eyes, level your countenance, entangle your speech, manifest in your demeanor, and shape your heart. This is using fire to fight fire, water to fight water—what is called "increasing excess." Once begun, there is no end.'* as Guō Qìngfān comments, *'Dazzle is a loan for* míng *(●), from the* Shuōwén*: "● means confusion. From eye, with* yíng *省 as phonetic."'* as Chéng comments, *'Manifest means to appear.'* *Your eyes will be confounded, your countenance will humble itself, your mouth will scramble to defend, your demeanor will grow ever more reverent, and your heart will abandon its own rightness to conform to their wrongs. Their evils are already manifold, and you will only add to them. Having begun thus, you will follow without end.*

*How perilous it is to speak profoundly before earning trust!'* as Xuān comments, *'Not yet trusted yet offering deep admonition.'* *Note: This "how" is read as "if." You will surely die before the violent ruler.*

*Moreover, in ancient times, Jié executed Guān Lóngféng, and Zhòu executed Prince Bǐgān—both cultivated their persons and, humbling themselves, cherished the people,'* as Lǐ comments, *'Cherished means to show compassion.'* as Xuān comments, *'People here refers to the ruler.'* *They served those below in defiance of their superiors, and thus their rulers, seizing upon their cultivation, cast them out. They were lovers of fame. Seizing upon their love of cultivation and fame, they ensnared them. This is one proof.*

*Formerly, Yáo attacked Cóngzhī and Xū’áo, and Yǔ attacked Yǒuhù—three states. Their lands became wastelands and their people vengeful ghosts,'* as Xuān comments, *'Their lands became ruins, their people became malevolent spirits.'* *Their persons suffered punishment and execution. Their use of arms did not cease; their pursuit of substance knew no end. Pursuit of substance means greed for profit. These three states were thus, so Yáo and Yǔ attacked and annihilated them. They were seekers of fame and substance. This is a second proof.*

As Sū Yǔ comments, *'Lóngféng and Bǐgān cultivated virtue, yet Jié and Zhòu deemed them lovers of fame and thus cast them out. Jié and Zhòu, resenting that upright ministers possessed beauty while they themselves were ashamed to be tyrannical kings, were also lovers of fame. Cóngzhī, Xū’áo, and Yǒuhù, ceaselessly employing arms to seek substance, were attacked and annihilated by Yáo and Yǔ—who themselves were not without the pursuit of substance.'*"

"Thus it is said, *'These were all seekers of fame and substance.'* Have you alone not heard this? Fame and substance are what even the sage cannot overcome—how much less could one such as you! The Master once more recounted what he had heard to instruct him.

He spoke of rulers who occupy the name of lofty position and possess the substance of awe-inspiring power: even if a sage were to serve as their minister, he could not but be bent to their will—how much less could you! Yet if you must have some means, then come, try and tell me!"

*(Here "means" refers to the instruments by which one might hold fast.)*

*(The word "try" means to attempt.)*

Yan Hui said, "If I were upright yet humble, solemn yet modest, and diligent yet single-minded, would that suffice?"

The Master replied, "Bah! How could that suffice? When one’s yang qi is so full that it bursts forth in ostentation, when one’s countenance shifts without constancy—these are things the common man dare not oppose. Instead, one suppresses what moves others, seeking to indulge one’s own heart. As Cheng says, *'Suppresses' means to restrain; 'indulge' means to take pleasure. When others are moved by admonition, one instead crushes them to gratify one’s own desires.* Such a one may be said to lack even the virtue of daily gradual cultivation—how much less could he attain great virtue! He clings rigidly to his own views, outwardly compliant yet inwardly unexamined. As Xuan says, *'He believes himself to be right.' 'Outwardly compliant' means appearing to agree, while 'inwardly unexamined' means lacking self-reflection.* As Yao Nai says, *'Unmeasured' means not weighing the meaning of a gentleman’s words.* How could this ever be acceptable?"

"Then," Yan Hui continued, "what if I were inwardly straight but outwardly yielding, and took the ancients as my model?

*('Then' marks the beginning of Yan Hui’s further response.)*

By 'inwardly straight,' I mean aligning myself with Heaven. One who aligns with Heaven knows that the Son of Heaven and oneself are both children of Heaven—so why should I seek that others deem my words good or bad? As Cheng says, *'Inwardly sincere and straight, one joins the company of natural principle.'* As Xuan says, *'The Son of Heaven is the ruler of men.'* As Guo says, *'All people, regardless of rank, receive life equally. Thus, whether good or bad, one leaves it to impartial judgment, seeking nothing from others.'* One who does so is called a childlike innocent—this is what it means to align with Heaven. By 'outwardly yielding,' I mean aligning with others. Raising the tablet, kneeling upright, bowing deeply—these are the rites of a minister. Everyone else does them; how could I alone refuse? One who does what others do incurs no blame—this is what it means to align with others.

By 'taking the ancients as my model,' I mean aligning with antiquity. As Cheng says, *'Admonishing with loyalty is accomplished in the present; the righteousness of ruler and minister is compared to antiquity.'* Though my words may resemble ancient teachings, they carry the substance of reproof. What the ancients had, I do not possess. One who does so may be straight yet free from harm. As Guo says, *'By lodging one’s straightness in antiquity, one avoids bringing harm upon oneself.'* This is what it means to align with antiquity. If I were thus, would that suffice?"

Confucius said, "Alas! How could this suffice? Excessive governance—*Guo*: 'When principle admits no duality, yet one imposes threefold regulations to govern, this is what is called excessive governance.'*—though lawful, is not expedient. *Yu*: 'These four characters form a phrase. In the *Liezi* it says, "Form expedites the completion of radiance." The *Shiwen* glosses *die* as 'convenient and artful.' Here, *die* carries the same meaning: to have laws yet not be artful.' Though steadfast, one remains blameless—though not grand, one may avoid fault. Yet this is all there is to it; how could it reach transformation? It is insufficient to transform others—it is still to follow one’s own mind." *Cheng*: "To follow what is in one’s mind."

Yan Hui said, "I have no further progress to make. May I ask for the method?"

Confucius said, "Fast. I shall tell you! To do this while still possessing [intent]—is it easy? *Guo*: 'To act with intent in mind is truly not easy.' Those who find it easy contravene the vastness of Heaven and are unfit." *Cheng*: "*Er Ya*: 'Summer is called *haotian*.' This refers to its qi being vast and expansive." *Note*: This contradicts the principle of natural emptiness. *Su Yu*: "Those who find it easy still follow their own minds. To lose one’s original mind is to violate Heaven." This interpretation also accords with the meaning.

Yan Hui said, "My family is poor. For months now, I have abstained from wine and meat. Would this suffice as fasting?"

Confucius said, "This is the fasting of sacrificial rites, not the fasting of the mind."

Yan Hui said, "May I ask about the fasting of the mind?"

Confucius said, "Unify your will—*Xuan*: 'Do not let it be mixed.'—do not listen with the ears but listen with the mind; *Cheng*: 'Empty the ear-root, concentrate the spirit to align with the mind.' do not listen with the mind but listen with qi. *Cheng*: 'The mind possesses awareness and still gives rise to attachments; qi is without emotion or thought, empty and yielding, responsive to things. Thus, abandon that awareness and take up this emptiness and yielding. Progress through successive relinquishments to ascend to the mysterious.' Hearing stops at the ears—*Xuan*: 'Stops at the form.' *Yu*: 'This should read, "The ears stop at hearing," transposed in transmission. This elaborates the meaning of "do not listen with the ears": the ears’ function stops at hearing alone, hence one does not listen with the ears.' The mind stops at alignment. *Yu*: 'This elaborates the meaning of "do not listen with the mind": the mind’s function stops at alignment alone, hence one does not listen with the mind. *Fu* means to accord; to accord with things is not the same as being empty and awaiting things.' Qi is that which is empty and awaits things. *Yu*: 'This elaborates the meaning of qi.' *Xuan*: 'Qi has no origin; this is emptiness.' Only the Dao abides in emptiness. Emptiness is the fasting of the mind." *Cheng*: "Only this true Dao abides in the empty mind. Thus, emptiness is the wondrous Dao of the fasting of the mind."

Yan Hui said, "Before receiving your instruction, I could not practice this fasting of the mind. In truth, it was still I—still seeing myself as Hui. Now that I have received your teaching, it is as though there were no Hui. *Cheng*: 'Having received the command, one forgets both things and self.' Can this be called emptiness?"

The Master said, "It is complete. *Cheng*: 'The wondrousness of the fasting of the mind is complete.' I shall tell you! If you can enter and wander within its bounds without being moved by its fame—when you enter Wei, you may wander within its borders without being swayed by empty reputation—speak when my words enter, and cease when they do not. *Cheng*: 'If my words enter, then speak; if they do not, then temporarily desist.' Have no gate, no embankment—*Xuan*: 'Do not open a single crevice, do not administer a single remedy.' *Guo*: 'Let things be as they are—this is having no gate. Entrust the world to its own peace—this is having no embankment.' *Li Zhen*: '*Men* and *du* form a parallel construction; *du* is not of the same category as *men*. The *Shuowen* says, "*Du*: thickness. Harmful grasses often grow abundantly." This meaning does not fit. *Du* is likely a loan for *dao*. Under *dao* in the *Shuowen*, it says, "To protect; also called high earth; read like *du*." This aligns with Guo’s gloss of "self-peace." Zhang Xingfu’s *Shuowen Fayi* says, "*Dao* is earth piled into a platform for transmitting signals, as in the *Lüshi Chunqiu*: 'Build a high *bao* to pray on the royal road, place a drum upon it, so that those far and near may hear.' *Dao* is a corruption of *bao*. *Dao* is a place of protection and defense, hence its meaning is borrowed for protection.' The *Zhouyi* says, 'With this, one embankments the world, and the people follow'; Laozi says, 'Level it, embankment it'—in these three instances, *du* carries this meaning. The *Guangya* says, '*Du*: peace.' This is also this sense.' *Note*: Xuan’s explanation, based on the surface of the text, is not as fitting as Li’s interpretation. A gate is that by which one may follow a path; an embankment is that by which one may gaze as a marker."

Without gates, without embankments—this leaves no trace for others to spy or point toward. Dwelling in a single abode within the realm of necessity, one approaches the subtle. Cheng says: "An abode is a dwelling place. To reside in the Dao of utmost unity, responding only when unavoidable and not through premeditation—this is to be nearly there." To erase one’s tracks is easy; to walk without leaving a trace upon the earth is difficult. Xuan says: "In navigating the world, not acting is easy; acting without imprinting traces is hard." To be employed by man is easy to feign; to be employed by Heaven is difficult to feign. Cheng says: "Human sentiments drive and compel, shallow and easily deceived; Heaven’s natural command is enacted yet hard to distort." We have heard of flying with wings, but never of flying without wings; we have heard of knowing with knowledge, but never of knowing without knowledge. *Shiwen* notes: "The first *zhi* is read as 'wisdom,' the second as the common word." Xuan says: "To move by spirit, to illuminate by stillness." Gazing upon that emptiness, the empty chamber generates whiteness. Sima says: "Emptiness is void. The chamber is a metaphor for the heart. When the heart is empty and void, pure whiteness arises alone." Cheng says: "That is the preceding realm. Observing the myriad existences, all are ultimately empty and still—thus one may empty the chamber of the heart and thereby illuminate the true source." Auspiciousness rests. Cheng says: "Auspicious blessings rest in the heart of concentrated stillness, which can also bring forth good responses." Yu says: "The repetition of *zhi* in 'rests rests' serves no meaningful purpose. The *Huainanzi*’s *Chuzhen Xun* states: 'The empty chamber generates whiteness; auspiciousness rests.' It is likely that the second *zhi* in this text is also a corruption of *ye* ('also'). Lu Chongyuan’s commentary on the *Liezi*’s *Tianrui* chapter reads: 'The empty chamber generates whiteness; auspiciousness rests alone'—this also confirms the error of the repeated *zhi*." *Note*: The second *zhi* may be a corruption of *zhi* ("it"). If one does not rest, this is called sitting while galloping. When the spirit roams outward without settling, the body sits while the heart races. If one follows the ears and eyes to communicate inwardly while excluding the heart’s knowledge, even ghosts and spirits will come to dwell—how much more so men! Li says: "*Xun* means 'to employ.'" Xuan says: "The ears and eyes are external, yet one employs them inwardly; the heart’s wisdom is internal, yet one banishes it outwardly." Cheng says: "With an empty mind that accommodates things, ghosts and spirits will obscurely attach and dwell. Human relationships will naturally follow suit." This is the transformation of the myriad things, the pivot upon which Yu and Shun acted, the path that Fuxi and Jiqu brought to completion—how much more so for those who are scattered and dispersed! This is the guiding principle by which Yu and Shun responded to things, the conduct and cessation of the ancient sage-kings—how could those who are merely scattered individuals not be transformed by it? Cheng says: "Jiqu refers to the rulers before the Three Sovereigns, who left no written records." Su Yu says: "This means that understanding this enables one to become an emperor or king, to govern the world—how much more so for a fragmented, scattered person!" This interpretation is also valid.

Lord Ye of Zigao was about to be sent on a mission to Qi. He asked Confucius: "The king’s charge to me, Zhu Liang, is most weighty." Cheng says: "The commission is of great importance." "Qi’s treatment of envoys will likely be outwardly respectful but slow in action." Xuan says: "They will appear respectful but delay in addressing matters." "Even a common man cannot be moved lightly—how much less a feudal lord! I am deeply fearful." *Li* means "fear." "You often told me, Zhu Liang: 'In matters great or small, few are not brought to joyful completion through the Dao. In affairs of any size, few are not successfully resolved through the Dao with joyful accord. If the matter fails, there will inevitably be human-caused calamities—the king will surely impose punishment. If it succeeds, there will inevitably be afflictions from yin and yang.'" Xuan says: "Joy and fear will clash, and the two qi of yin and yang will be harmed, leading to illness." "Only one with virtue can ensure that, whether the matter succeeds or fails, no calamity follows." Cheng says: "To entrust success or failure to the path ahead without allowing joy or sorrow to burden the heart—only a person of great virtue can do this." *The above recounts your words.* Su Yu says: "This means that in matters without success or failure, one can ultimately avoid calamity only through great virtue." *Note*: Cheng’s interpretation somewhat resembles Zhang Jun’s defeat at Fuli, which cannot serve as a model. Su’s interpretation is correct.

"In my daily life, I hold to coarse fare without seeking refinement," Xuan says: "Content with simple, rough food, not pursuing fine delicacies." "My cooking fire requires no one to seek coolness," Cheng says: "Cool here means cool air. Yet the fire is not excessive, so there is no heat to avoid." "Now, having received my morning orders, by evening I drink ice—is this not internal heat within me? The cause of burning anxiety. I have not yet reached the truth of the matter," Xuan says: "Not yet arrived at the actual execution of the task," "yet I already suffer afflictions from yin and yang. If the matter fails, there will surely be human-caused calamity—the king will impose punishment. These are two dangers. As a minister, I am not equal to such a burden. You must have something to tell me!"

Confucius said: "Under heaven there are two great precepts," Cheng says: "Precepts here mean laws." "The first is fate; the second is duty. Your love for your parents is fate, something the heart cannot untie—received from heaven, naturally and firmly bound. Serving one’s lord is duty: there is no place where he is not your lord, nowhere to escape within heaven and earth," Cheng says: "There has never been a land without a lord under heaven." "These are called the great precepts. Therefore, in serving one’s parents, one finds peace without choosing the place," regardless of circumstances, seeking only their comfort—"this is the utmost of filial piety. In serving one’s lord, one finds peace without choosing the task," Cheng says: "Whether the task is smooth or perilous, one accepts it as fate," "this is the height of loyalty. In serving one’s own heart, sorrow and joy do not alter what lies before it," Wang Niansun says: "*Shi* should be read as *yi* (alter). This means unchanging. The *Yanzi Chunqiu*’s *Waipian* says 'lord and minister alter their positions,' the *Xunzi*’s *Ruxiao* chapter says 'sorrow and emptiness alter each other,' and the *Hanshu*’s *Wei Wan zhuan* says 'what people alter,' all sharing this meaning. Stated directly, it is *yi shi* (altering); inverted, it is *shi yi* (altered)." Xuan says: "Serving the heart as one serves lord and parents, without choosing—though the circumstances of sorrow and joy differ, one does not allow them to alter what lies before." "Knowing the inevitable yet accepting it as fate—this is the utmost of virtue. As a minister or child, one is fundamentally constrained by what cannot be avoided. Proceeding according to the truth of the matter, one forgets oneself," *qing* here means reality. "How then could one have leisure to delight in life and fear death?" Xuan says: "How could one still suffer afflictions from yin and yang?" "You may proceed thus! I shall now repeat what I have heard: In all relations," referring to neighboring states, "those near must rely on trust in their closeness," Xuan says: "Mutually cherishing trust in their conduct." "Those far must be loyal in their words," Xuan says: "Mutually assured through speech." "Words must sometimes be conveyed by others," Xuan says: "One must entrust messengers to transmit them." "Conveying words of mutual joy or mutual anger between rulers—this is the most difficult task under heaven. When both are pleased, there will be much exaggerated praise; when both are angry, there will be much exaggerated slander," Guo says: "*Yi* means excess. Words spoken in joy or anger often exceed what is proper." "All exaggeration is akin to falsehood," Cheng says: "*Lei* means resemblance. It resembles making people fabricate falsely." "Falsehood then makes trust impossible," Cheng says: "*Mo* conveys suspicion." "Suspicion then brings misfortune to the messenger. Thus, the *Fayan* says," citing an ancient maxim (the *Fayan* by Yang Xiong takes its name from this). "'Convey the constant truth,'" Xuan says: "Only transmit what is plain and real." "'Do not convey exaggerated words,'" Guo says: "Though one hears excessive words spoken in the moment, do not transmit them." "'Then one may nearly preserve wholeness.'" Xuan says: "One may nearly preserve oneself." *Note*: End of citation from the *Fayan*.

"Moreover, those who contend with skill and strength begin with openness but invariably end in secrecy; when the struggle grows intense, many strange tactics emerge." *Shiwen* notes: "*Da* is read as *tai*, also written as 泰." *Note*: Contending with strength belongs to yang, but seeking victory ends in yin schemes; when the desire to win becomes extreme, a hundred devious tricks arise. "Those who drink with ritual begin with order but invariably end in chaos; when the drinking grows excessive, many strange pleasures arise. Ritual drinking resembles order, but once drunk, it ends in confusion; when intoxication reaches its height, every kind of indulgence follows. All things are likewise: they begin with sincerity but invariably end in vulgarity."

"Xuan comments: '*Liang* means sincerity; *bi* means deceit.' Yu remarks: '*Liang* and *bi* do not form a proper antithesis. *Liang* is likely a scribal error for *zhu*. *Zhu* is read as *du*. The *Shi Jing* states, 'In Song there is Mengzhu,' while the *Records of the Grand Historian: Annals of Xia* writes it as 'Mingdu'—this is a case in point. 'Beginning with *du*, invariably ending in *bi*'—*du* and *bi* form a true antithesis. Due to the interchangeability of the character *zhu*, it was mistakenly written as *liang*, thus losing the original meaning. The *Huainanzi: Explanations of Dao* reads, 'Thus what begins with *du* commonly grows vast in *bi*,' which is drawn from the *Zhuangzi* and may be used to correct the text. The character *da* in that passage is an error for *zu*. See Wang’s *Miscellaneous Notes* for further discussion.'

What begins simply will invariably end in grandeur. Speech is like wind and waves: it comes as the wind rises, it surges as the waves swell. Action is a matter of gain and loss. Guo Songtao explains: '*Shi* means to possess and retain; *sang* means to indulge and abandon. *Shi* and *sang* are akin to gain and loss.' Wind and waves are easily stirred, and gain and loss are easily imperiled. Since gain and loss have no fixed outcome, it is said they are 'easily imperiled.'

Thus, anger arises without cause, and artful speech with biased words. The onset of anger has no other source—it commonly stems from artful words that exceed reality and biased speech that misses the mark. When a beast is dying, it does not choose its cries; its breath comes in ragged gasps, and in that moment, a vicious intent arises within. When a beast is cornered and near death, its cries are indiscriminate, and its pent-up fury remains. At such a time, it may turn its heart to malice, seeking to bite. This beastly malice serves as a metaphor for the ignoble intentions of petty men. When scrutiny becomes excessive, ignoble intentions inevitably arise in response, though one remains unaware of it. When demands for precision and exactitude grow too severe, others will respond with ignoble intentions, unaware of why they do so. If one remains unaware of this process, who can foresee where it will end?" Xuan comments:

"disaster will surely befall."

Thus, the *Fa Yan* states: "Do not alter decrees," as Cheng explains: "When the lord’s command is truly transmitted, it may not be changed." "Do not urge completion," as Cheng explains: "Do not labor to exhort or reward, forcing things to be accomplished." The quotation from the *Fa Yan* ends here.

Excess is an increase. If one goes beyond the original measure, it amounts to an amplification of words. Altering decrees and urging completion are perilous affairs—such matters will inevitably become precarious.

Good outcomes require time; evil outcomes cannot be undone. What is accomplished as good does not rest in a single moment; what is accomplished as evil will surely leave no time for correction. How can one not be cautious!

Moreover, riding things to roam the mind, entrusting oneself to necessity to nurture the center—this is perfection. Xuan comments: "Following things to wander and lodge the mind, entrusting oneself to necessity in response without contrivance, to nurture the unmoving center of the mind—this is the ultimate principle of the Dao."

Why act in repayment? Guo comments: "Entrusting oneself to what is naturally repaid, why insist on deliberate action!"

It is best to deliver the command. This is the difficult part: merely conveying the lord’s command without inserting oneself—this is the challenge.

If it is a matter of human principles, it is not a true calamity.

When Yan He was about to serve as tutor to the crown prince of Duke Ling of Wei, as the *Shi Wen* notes: "Yan He, a worthy man of Lu. The crown prince was Kuai Kui," he asked Qu Boyu, "There is a man here whose virtue is heaven-sent destruction—by nature, he delights in killing. If I deal with him without restraint, he will endanger our state." Xuan comments: "If one indulges his transgressions, he will surely overthrow the nation." "If I deal with him with restraint, he will endanger my person." "If I regulate him with laws and standards, he will first harm me."

"His knowledge suffices only to recognize others’ faults but not the cause of those faults." As the *Shi Wen* notes: "His knowledge, pronounced as *zhi*." He only knows how to blame others, not his own errors.

"In such a case, what am I to do?"

Qu Boyu said, "Excellent question! Be vigilant, be cautious—first rectify yourself! First seek to be without fault yourself.

In form, nothing is better than closeness; in mind, nothing is better than harmony." Xuan comments: "Outwardly, show the form of intimate attachment; inwardly, harbor the intent of harmonious compliance."

"Yet even these two have their dangers." Xuan comments: "They are still not perfectly good."

Closeness must not become intrusion; harmony must not become overt. Attachment must not run too deep, lest one fail to guard against his excesses; compliance must not be too apparent, lest one fail to guide his tendencies.

If form becomes too close and intrusive, it will lead to collapse, ruin, destruction, and downfall. Collapse means falling; ruin means annihilation; destruction means breaking; downfall means stumbling.

If the mind becomes too harmonious and overt, it will lead to reputation, fame, misfortune, and calamity. Guo comments: "If one displays harmony, it will give rise to a reputation for bearing disgrace; if one aids his fame, he will resent being surpassed and falsely generate misfortune and calamity."

If he acts like a child, then act like a child with him—this is a metaphor for lacking discernment.

If he acts without boundaries, then act without boundaries with him—this is a metaphor for minor transgressions.

If he acts without limits, then act without limits with him—this means not establishing rigid barriers.

Understand him, and enter into flawlessness. Follow his intentions to comprehend them, and thereby enter into a state without blemish.

Do you not know the mantis? It brandished its arms to block the wheel tracks of a chariot, unaware that it was unequal to the task—such was the beauty of its talent.

Be vigilant, be cautious! If one accumulates boasting and beauty to provoke him, it will be perilous indeed. *Er* means "you." *Fa* means "to boast of merit." Beauty cannot be relied upon; if you accumulate your beauty and boast of your beauty to provoke the crown prince, you will come close to the mantis’s folly. This is one analogy.

Do you not know those who raise tigers? They dare not feed them living creatures, for fear of provoking their killing rage; they dare not feed them whole creatures, for fear of provoking their tearing rage. Cheng comments: "When throwing dead prey to a tiger, they first tear it apart to avoid requiring effort."

They time their hunger and satiety and understand their angry impulses. Tigers, though of a different species from humans, fawn upon those who feed them—this is compliance. Thus, those they kill are those who oppose them. If a tiger opposes, it kills; if it is fed, it fawns. This is a metaphor for how one must not provoke anger. This is another analogy.

Those who love horses gather their dung in baskets and collect their urine in conches—so says Cheng: "Conches are large clamshells." This is the utmost in loving horses. Yet when horseflies or gadflies alight upon them (Wang Niansun notes: "The character 仆 here means 'to attach.' The phrase describes flies clinging to the horse's body. The *Book of Songs* says, 'The illustrious mandate has its attachments,' and Mao's commentary explains: 'Attachments are what cling.'"), and one pats them at the wrong moment (Cheng explains: "拊 means to slap. 不时 means catching the horse unawares"), the horse may break its bit, shatter its bridle, or smash its chest. The horse is startled to this degree. When one’s intentions are carried to an extreme, love may turn to loss—how can one not be cautious? This is a third analogy.

A carpenter named Shi traveled to Qi and arrived at Qu Yuan, where he saw a sacred oak used as the village shrine. (Shi is the carpenter’s name. 之 means "to go." Sima explains: "Qu Yuan is a winding road." Cheng comments: "Like the road of Huanyuan." 社 refers to the earth deity; the oak is the sacred tree of the shrine.) The tree was so vast it could shade thousands of oxen, its girth measured in a hundred spans (the *Wen Xuan* commentary cites Sima: "絜 means 'to encircle.'"), and Li explains: "A span is the width of one hand; this tree was likely ten *zhang* in circumference." Its height towered above the mountain by ten *ren* before branches appeared, and its side limbs that could be made into boats numbered nearly ten. (Yu notes: "旁 and 方 were interchangeable in ancient times. 方 means 'nearly.' Thus, the limbs suitable for boats numbered nearly ten.") Spectators gathered like a marketplace, yet the master carpenter paid no heed and walked on without pause. (遂 means "to proceed." The *Wen Xuan* commentary cites Sima: "Shi was the carpenter’s courtesy name.") His disciple, sated with the sight, ran to catch up with the carpenter and said: "Since I first took up the axe to follow you, master, I have never seen timber so magnificent. Why do you refuse to look and walk on without stopping?"

The carpenter replied: "Enough! Say no more! It is a useless tree. If made into a boat, it would sink; its weight is too great. If made into a coffin or outer coffin, it would rot quickly; its grain is too coarse. If made into vessels, they would break at once; its wood is too brittle. If used for doors or gates, it would ooze resin (Li Zhen notes: "The *Guangyun* defines 樠 as 'the heartwood of pine' or the name of a tree. Pine heartwood contains sap, and 液樠 refers to this property."). If used for pillars, it would be eaten by worms. It is a worthless tree, good for nothing—this has been discussed in earlier chapters such as the *Free and Easy Wandering*. That is why it has lived so long."

When the carpenter returned home, the sacred oak appeared to him in a dream and said: "With what would you compare me? Would you liken me to the so-called 'patterned wood'?" (Guo explains: "Patterned wood refers to all usable timber, which can be carved into designs.") "Consider the crabapple, pear, orange, pomelo, and other fruit-bearing trees (Cheng comments: "蓏 refers to melons and gourds."). When their fruit ripens, they are stripped and humiliated; their great branches are broken, and their small branches are dragged away (Yu notes: "泄 should be read as 曳. In the *Xunzi*'s *Fei Xiang* chapter, 'When meeting people, one uses dragging,' and Yang's commentary explains: 'Dragging means to pull.' 小枝泄 refers to branches being pulled."). This is because their usefulness brings suffering to their lives, so they do not complete their natural lifespan but die prematurely—struck down by the world’s conventions through their own actions (Cheng comments: "掊 means to strike."). All things are like this. As for me, I have sought to be of no use for a long time; I nearly died, nearly felled to my death. Only now have I attained this state (Guo explains: "Many have looked at me with disdain, but only now has the carpenter understood me."), which is of great use to me. If I had been useful, would I have grown so vast? Moreover, you and I are both things—how could you judge me as a thing? You, a nearly dead, worthless man, what could you know of a worthless tree?" (而 here means "you." 几 means "nearly.")

The carpenter Shi awoke and interpreted his dream. Wang Niansun notes: "‘Diagnosed’ (*zhěn*) should be read as *zhěn* (告, ‘to inform’)." He informed his disciples of the dream. A disciple asked: "If you hasten to seek uselessness, then why serve as a shrine tree?" Since you urgently pursue uselessness to preserve your life, why must you become a shrine tree to adorn yourself?

He replied: "Hush!" (This means "keep it secret." Yao Nai notes: "密 and 默 are interchangeable. In the *Tian Zifang* chapter, Confucius says: ‘Silence! Say no more!’ In the *Dasheng* chapter, it reads: ‘The Duke kept it secret and did not respond.’") Say no more! That tree merely lodges there, enduring the scorn and reproach of those who do not understand it. It merely dwells by the shrine, bearing the slander of those who fail to know it without complaint. Sima Biao comments: "厉 means ‘affliction.’"

If it were not a shrine tree, it would nearly have been felled! If it were not a shrine tree, it would almost have been cut down—perhaps split into firewood. Moreover, what it preserves differs from the multitude. It is safeguarded in the mountains and wilds, ultimately distinct from the common crowd, not to be likened to a city fox or shrine rat. To praise it by conventional standards—is this not far off the mark? Xuan comments: "义 means ‘common principle.’" The point is: that tree does not rely on the shrine spirit for self-glorification, so to commend it by conventional reasoning is far from its true nature.

Nanbo Ziqi wandered in Shangqiu. Li notes: "This is Nan Guo. *Bo* means ‘elder.’" Sima Biao comments: "Shangqiu is present-day Suiyang County in Liang Province." There he saw an extraordinary tree so vast that a thousand chariots could gather beneath it, sheltering in its luxuriant shade. Xiang comments: "藾 means ‘shade.’"

Cui comments: "隐 means ‘afflicted by heat.’" Cheng explains: "A team of four horses is called a *cheng*. This says that a thousand chariots could gather, finding shelter from the heat in its shade." Ziqi said: "What tree is this? It must possess extraordinary timber!"—meaning it must surely be fit for use as lumber.

Yet when he looked up at its slender branches, they were gnarled and crooked, unfit for ridgepoles or beams. When he looked down at its massive roots, they were split at the core, useless for coffins or outer caskets. Cheng notes: "轴 resembles the turning of a wheel’s axle—here referring to the heartwood. *解* means the grain is loose and scattered, not tightly bound." Chewing its leaves burned the mouth and caused injury; inhaling its scent induced a raving drunkenness that lasted three days without cease. Li explains: "狂酲 means ‘madness like intoxication.’ *酲* refers to the sickness of wine."

Ziqi said: "This tree is truly a worthless wood, and yet it has grown to such immense size!" Cheng comments: "Being worthless is the great timber of perfect life; having no use is the wondrous utility that aids all things. Thus it escapes the axe’s early doom and shelters a thousand chariots in its shade." Alas! The spirit-like person takes this as worthlessness!

The insight moves from wood to man. Xuan comments: "The spirit-like person likewise, by not displaying his talents, remains useless to the world, and thus Heaven alone preserves him whole."

In Song there was a region called Jing, where catalpa, cypress, and mulberry flourished. Of these trees, those with trunks the size of a man’s embrace or smaller were felled by those seeking pegs for monkeys; Sima explains: "A trunk held by two hands is called *gong*; by one hand, *ba*." Xuan notes: "*Yi* are tethering stakes." Those three or four spans in girth were cut down by those seeking fine timber for grand beams; Cui states: "Eight feet make one span." Guo Qingfan observes: "Here *ming* means ‘large’" (see *Under Heaven*, under "three hundred famous mountains"). Cheng comments: "*Li* refers to roof beams." Those seven or eight spans in girth were hewn by the households of nobles and wealthy merchants seeking single-plank coffin sides. *Shiwen* notes: "*Shan* is also written as *tan*." Cheng explains: "A coffin side made from a single unjoined plank is called *shanpang*. Such trees are enormous, requiring the cutting of vast boards." Thus, before completing their natural lifespan, they were prematurely felled by axes—this is the calamity of usefulness.

Therefore, when exorcists perform purifications, they reject oxen with white foreheads, pigs with upturned snouts, and men with hemorrhoids, deeming them unfit for sacrifice in the sacred river. Guo explains: "*Jie* refers to the exorcism rites of shamans." Cheng comments: "*Sang* is the forehead. *Kang* means ‘raised.’ These three cannot be taken to the numinous river for sacrifice." In ancient times, men were drowned in the river as offerings—when Ximen Bao served as magistrate of Ye, he put an end to this practice, which was of this kind. The shamans knew this well. *Yi* and *yi* are interchangeable. Guo notes: "Even the shamans understood that the useless remain whole." What is deemed inauspicious is precisely what the spirit-like person regards as the greatest auspice. Xuan comments: "To preserve one’s life whole—what greater blessing could there be?" (In one edition, the character *er* appears above *yi*.)

There was a man named Zhili Shu, whose body was deformed. Sima describes: "Zhili depicts a form that is incomplete. *Shu* is his name." His chin rested on his navel, his shoulders rose above his head; Sima explains: "This describes a hunched back and a sunken head." *Huainanzi* states: "His spinal column towered above his head." His topknot pointed to the sky; Sima comments: "The *huicu* is a topknot. In ancient times, topknots were worn at the nape, but with a hunched back and lowered head, the topknot would point upward." Cui says: "*Huicu* refers to the cervical vertebrae." Li Zhen argues: "Cui’s interpretation is correct. In the *Great and Venerable Master* chapter, it says: ‘*Gouzui* points to the sky.’ Li explains: ‘*Gouzui* are the cervical vertebrae, resembling a growth.’ This aligns with Cui’s view. The *Suwen*’s *Cire* chapter states: ‘The third vertebra of the neck, where it is sunken, is the center.’ Wang’s commentary: ‘This outlines the general method for counting vertebrae.’ Shen Tong’s *Explanation of Bones* says: ‘The twenty-one vertebrae below the great cervical vertebra are collectively called the spine, and the bones are called vertebrae.’ The *Nanjing*’s forty-fifth difficulty states: ‘The bones gather at *dazhu*.’ Zhang’s commentary: ‘*Dazhu* is an acupoint located at the first vertebra behind the neck. From here, all bones branch downward like a framework, hence the bones gather at *dazhu*.’ *Huicu* derives its meaning from the gathering of bones and is situated among the cervical vertebrae, hence it is called the ‘cervical vertebra.’ *Chu Xue Ji* 19 cites *cuo* as written as *cuo*. The *Yupian* states: ‘*Cuo* is a knot in wood,’ which resembles a spinal node. The character written with the wood radical is more appropriate in meaning." The five *guan* were on his upper back; Li explains: "*Guan* are the *shu* points. The *shu* points of the five viscera are all located on a person’s back." Li Zhen comments: "The chin and shoulders describe external features; the *huicu* and five *guan* describe internal ones." His thighs aligned with his ribs. Sima explains: "With a hunched spine and raised belly, his thighs pressed against his ribs." He mended clothes and washed laundry, enough to feed himself; Sima notes: "*Cuo zhen* means sewing. *Xie* means washing clothes." He sifted grain and winnowed chaff, enough to feed ten people. Sima comments: "*Gu* means winnowing. A small winnowing basket is called *ce*. *Bo jing* means sifting rice." Cheng explains: "*Bo* means winnowing dust." When the authorities conscripted soldiers, Zhili rolled up his sleeves and wandered among them; Guo observes: "Relying on his uselessness, he did not hide himself." When the state undertook great labor projects, Zhili, citing his chronic illness, was exempt from service; Xuan notes: "He could not perform labor." When the authorities distributed grain to the sick, he received three *zhong* and ten bundles of firewood. Sima explains: "Six *hu* and four *dou* make one *zhong*." Thus, one whose form was deformed could still nourish his body and live out his natural years—how much more so one whose virtue is deformed!

Confucius traveled to Chu, where the madman Jieyu wandered near his gate, singing: "O phoenix! O phoenix! How your virtue has declined! The future cannot be awaited, the past cannot be retrieved. Guo explains: "One must act according to the present moment." When the world possesses the Dao, the sage accomplishes his work; Xuan comments: "He fulfills his purpose." Su Yu notes: "Zhuangzi alters the phrasing to show that the times are not favorable. If one lives in an age with the Dao, one would gladly serve the world, not merely preserve one’s life." When the world lacks the Dao, the sage preserves his life. Xuan comments: "He keeps himself whole." In the present age, one barely escapes punishment. Blessings are lighter than a feather, yet none know how to bear them—how easily taken, yet not taken! Disasters are heavier than the earth, yet none know how to avoid them."

"To fail to avoid what should be avoided!

Enough! Enough! To confront others with virtue!
Xuan comments: "What must urgently cease is the matter of displaying virtue to others."

Dangerous! Dangerous! To pace within lines drawn on the ground!
Xuan comments: "Most perilous are those who rigidly constrain themselves with suffering."

*Miyang, miyang*—this refers to thorns, which grow in mountain wilds and wound the feet if trodden upon. Even now, in our state of Chu, cart-drivers encountering them still cry, *'Miyang ti!'* The sound *mi* is read like *ma*.

Let nothing harm my path!
My path winds and bends,
Xuan comments: "Retreating in hesitation, not daring to take the straight way."

Let nothing harm my feet!"

The mountain trees bring ruin upon themselves; the fat of the lamp consumes itself.
Sima comments: "The tree becomes an axe-handle and thus cuts itself down; the fat ignites the flame and thus melts itself away."

Cinnamon is edible, so it is felled; lacquer is useful, so it is slashed.
Cheng comments: "The heart of the cinnamon is pungent and fragrant, so it suffers the axe; lacquer serves vessel-making, so it is cut. Both are destroyed by their own abilities for being useful."

All people know the use of the useful, yet none know the use of the useless.

This analogy clarifies the conclusion, though it does not directly follow Jie Yu’s song above—the song has rhyme, while this does not."

*Zhuangzi Jijie*, Scroll Two
*Inner Chapters: "Virtue’s Full Manifestation," Fifth*

Within, virtue is fully replete, and of itself bears outward signs of verification. In Lu there was a foot-amputee named Wang Tai—Li [Yi] notes: "Amputated feet are called *wu*." Those who followed him in study were as numerous as those who followed Confucius. Guo [Xiang] comments: "His disciples rivaled Confucius in number." Chang Ji asked Confucius, saying: "Wang Tai is a foot-amputee, yet those who follow him in study divide Lu equally with you." *Shiwen* notes: "Chang Ji—some say a disciple of Confucius; others, a worthy of Lu." "He stands without teaching, sits without discourse; yet those who go to him empty return full. His disciples all gain something. Is there truly a teaching without words, a shaping of the heart without form?" Xuan [Ying] comments: "Silent transformation." "What manner of man is he?"

Confucius said: "This Master is a sage. I, Qiu, have merely lagged behind and not yet gone to him. I intend to take him as my teacher—how much more those who are not my equal! Why only Lu! I would lead all under Heaven to follow him."

Chang Ji said: "He is a foot-amputee, yet he surpasses you, Master—his distinction from the common is indeed great. If this is so, how does he employ his mind?" Confucius said: "Life and death are great indeed, yet he does not change with them. His person moves with change, yet life and death do not alter him. Though heaven and earth collapse, he will not lose himself with them." Cheng [Xuanying] comments: "Lose: to be lost." "He does not follow them into ruin. He examines the flawless and does not shift with things." Guo Qingfan notes: "*Jia* is a scribal error for *xia*. The *Huainanzi*’s *Jingshen Xun* reads *shen hu wu xia*—'he examines his own flawlessness.' Thus he lets things shift of themselves without being enslaved by them. In the *Zuozhuan*, 'Fu Xia' appears in the *Zheng Shijia* as 'Fu Jia'; in the *Liji*’s *Tan Gong*, 'Gongjian Jia' appears in the *Hanshu*’s *Renbiao* as 'Gongjian Xia.' *Xia* and *jia* are graphically similar and easily confused." "He commands the transformations of things while guarding their root."

Xuan [Ying] comments: "He governs the transformations of things, holding fast to their pivot."

Chang Ji said: "What does this mean?" Confucius said: "Viewed from what makes them different, the liver and gallbladder are as distant as Chu and Yue—though part of one body, the world sees them as distinct. Viewed from what makes them the same, the myriad things are all one—all are a single thing between heaven and earth. If this is so, then he does not know what suits the ears and eyes—what the ears and eyes deem fitting for sound and form—yet his mind roams in the harmony of virtue." Guo [Xiang] comments: "He releases his mind amid the Dao and virtue, and all is vast and without obstruction." "He sees things in their unity and does not perceive their loss." Xuan [Ying] comments: "He sees the myriad things as one, without gain or loss." "He regards the loss of his foot as the shedding of dust."

Chang Ji said: "He cultivates himself—this is all Wang Tai can do. With his knowledge, he attains his heart; with his true knowing, he recovers the principle of his mind. With his heart, he attains the constant heart—again, with the principle of his mind, he awakens to the heart-principle constant through past and present. Why then do people flock to him?" *Zui*: to gather.

Confucius said: "People do not mirror themselves in flowing water but in still water—only stillness can still the many who seek stillness." Cheng [Xuanying] comments: "Mirror: to reflect." Xuan [Ying] comments: "Water does not seek to mirror, yet people come to mirror themselves in it. Only by being still can it still those who seek stillness." "Receiving its mandate from earth, only the pine and cypress remain—standing alone, green in winter and summer. Receiving its mandate from heaven, only Shun stands upright." Guo [Xiang] comments: "Below, only the pine and cypress stand alone; above, only the sage stands alone. Thus all who are not upright come seeking uprightness. If all things were evergreen, the pine and cypress would not be prized; if all people were self-upright, none would admire the great sage and flock to him." Cheng [Xuanying] comments: "The human head is above; remove it, and one dies. The tree’s root is below; remove it, and it dies. Thus we call humans 'upper heads' and trees 'lower heads.' The upper head eats the side heads, the side heads eat the lower heads. The lower heads are grasses and trees; the side heads, insects and beasts." "Fortunate is he who can upright his life, thereby uprighting the lives of others." Xuan [Ying] comments: "Shun could upright his own nature, and the natures of things all received uprightness of themselves."

As for the proof of preserving one’s original state—what is its evidence? The reality of fearlessness. A single brave warrior, boldly entering the nine armies—Cui [Zhuan] comments: "The Son of Heaven has six armies; the feudal lords, three—together, nine armies." He seeks fame and can compel himself—yet even he is like this. How much more one who governs heaven and earth, treasuries the myriad things—Cheng [Xuanying] comments: "He holds the warp of the two powers and embraces the cosmos"—who regards the six limbs as mere lodging—Xuan [Ying] comments: "*Zhi*: merely. He treats the six limbs as his temporary abode." Cheng [Xuanying] comments: "The six limbs: head, torso, and four limbs." "Takes the ears and eyes as mere semblances, and knows with the knowing of unity—*zhi* above refers to wisdom; *zhi* below, to its object. Pure and without duality. Has his heart never died?" Xuan [Ying] comments: "He attains the constant heart, unchanged by life and death." "He will choose a day to ascend in transcendence." *Jia*: Xu [Shen] reads as *xia*. Xuan [Ying] comments: "The *Liji* says: 'The Son of Heaven ascends in transcendence.' Here, it signifies departing the world and standing alone."

"Choosing a day" means selecting a specific day. *Note*: This refers to the Yellow Emperor’s journey to the Great Purity. Yet men cannot but follow such a path. Xuan [Ying] comments: "Men cannot abandon it of their own accord." How then would he deign to make worldly affairs his concern?

This answers Chang Ji’s suspicion that Dai might harbor ambitions to sway the masses.

Shentu Jia, an amputee, studied under Bohun Wuren alongside Zichan of Zheng. (In the "Miscellaneous Chapters," it is written as "Maoren.") Zichan said to Shentu Jia: "If I leave first, you stay behind; if you leave first, I will stay behind." Guo [Xiang] comments: "He was ashamed to walk alongside an amputee." The next day, they again sat together in the same hall. Zichan said to Shentu Jia: "If I leave first, you stay behind; if you leave first, I will stay behind. Now that I am about to leave, can you not stay behind? Or is it that you cannot?" Guo [Xiang] comments: "He asked bluntly, hoping Shentu Jia would not walk alongside him." "Moreover, when you see the one in power, you do not yield—do you consider yourself equal to the one in power?" *Zhizheng*: Zichan refers to himself. *Wei*: to avoid. *Ye qi*: to be equal. He rebuked Shentu Jia for his lack of deference.

Shentu Jia replied: "In the Master’s gate, are there truly those who take pride in their power as you do? The gate of Master Bohun values virtue and power—how could it harbor those who boast of their authority as you do? You delight in your own power and thus place yourself above others! I have heard it said: ‘When a mirror is bright, dust does not settle; if dust settles, the mirror is no longer bright.’ To dwell long with the worthy is to be without fault." *Zhi*: to settle. A clear mirror gathers no dust; to be near the virtuous is to be without fault. "Now, the greater one you seek is the Master, yet you still speak in this way—is this not a fault?" Xuan [Ying] comments: "*Qu da*: to seek broad understanding." *Note*: *Qu da* means to hold in high regard.

Zichan said: "Since you are as you are—already maimed in form—do you still vie with Yao in goodness?" Xuan [Ying] comments: "Yao represents the pinnacle of goodness, hence the comparison." "Do you not reflect that your virtue is insufficient?" Xuan [Ying] comments: "Considering your past conduct, you must have committed faults that led to your amputation—do you not reflect on this?"

Shentu Jia said: "Those who openly acknowledge their faults yet do not deserve to lose their limbs are many; those who do not acknowledge their faults yet do not deserve to keep their limbs are few. *Zhuang*: to make manifest. Many openly declare their transgressions yet do not deserve to lose their feet; few do not declare their transgressions yet still lose their feet. To know there is nothing to be done and accept it as fate—only those of virtue can do this." Xuan [Ying] comments: "To regard amputation as the natural course of fate and not be troubled by it—only those of virtue can do this." "Wandering within Yi’s drawn bow, the center is the mark, yet whether one hits it or not is a matter of fate. The first two *zhong* are read as written; the latter two are pronounced *zhòng*. The drawn bow of Yi is a metaphor for the net of punishment. To dwell within the net of punishment, who can be certain they are without fault? That one is not struck by punishment is merely a matter of chance. Many with whole limbs mock me for my incomplete limbs. I was enraged, but when I came to the Master’s place, my anger dissipated and I returned to myself." Guo [Xiang] comments: "He abandoned his former anger and returned to his usual state." "Was it not the Master who cleansed me with goodness? He purified my heart’s burdens with the Way of virtue. I have accompanied you, Master, for nineteen years, yet you have never once regarded me as an amputee. I have never heard the Master reject me for my maimed form. Now you and I wander within the realm of form and bone, befriending one another through virtue and power. Yet you seek me out beyond the realm of form and bone, measuring me by outward traces. Is this not a fault?"

Zichan, startled, changed his expression and said: "Do not speak thus!" He rose in apology. *Nai zhe*: meaning "like this." *Zi wu nai cheng*: "Do not speak in this way." In the "Great Master" chapter, "not knowing why it is thus" also means "not knowing why it is like this."

In Lu, there was an amputee named Shushan Wuzhi. Li [Yi] comments: "Shushan is his clan name." Xuan [Ying] comments: "Without toes, he took it as his name." He came to see Confucius on his heels. Cui [Wei] comments: "Without toes, he walked on his heels." Confucius said: "You were not careful, and already you have suffered such misfortune. Though you come now, what can be done?" Wuzhi said: "I was ignorant of my duties and recklessly used my body, which is why I lost my feet. Now that I have come, what remains is something more honorable than feet." Xuan [Ying] comments: "What is more honorable than feet does not reside in the body." "I strive to preserve it whole. Heaven covers all without exception; Earth bears all without exception. I took you, Master, to be like Heaven and Earth—how could I have known you would be like this?"

Confucius said: "I am indeed narrow-minded. Why do you not come in? Please share what you have heard!" Wuzhi departed. Xuan [Ying] comments: "He left directly." Confucius said: "Disciples, take heed! This Wuzhi, an amputee, still strives to learn to make amends for his past misdeeds—how much more should those of whole virtue do so!" Past misdeeds harm one’s virtue; one seeks learning to repair them—how much more should those without misdeeds and of whole virtue do so!

Wuzhi said to Lao Dan: "Is Confucius not yet a perfected person? Why does he so frequently take you as his teacher?" Yu [Yue] comments: "*Binbin*: meaning repeatedly. The characters for *bin* and *pin* were interchangeable in ancient times. The *Guangya* explains: '*Pinpin*: in succession.'"

Guo [Xiang] comments: "He found it strange that Confucius was once again studying under Lao Dan." That man sought to make his name known through bizarre, illusory, and monstrous means—did he not know that the perfected person regards these as his own shackles? Li [Yi] comments: "*Zhuogui*: meaning strange and extraordinary." Note: The *Lüshi Chunqiu*'s "Mourning Music" chapter writes it as "*chugui*." Wood on the foot is called a *zhi*; on the hand, a *gu*. *Qi* and *qi* are interchangeable. This says he sought to make himself known to the world as an extraordinary man, yet did not realize that the perfected person views fame as his own shackles.

Lao Dan said: "Why not simply let him see death and life as a single thread, the permissible and impermissible as one continuous strand, and thus release his shackles—would that be possible?" This means that life and death, right and wrong, can be united as one—why not let him see death and life, right and wrong, as a single thread, thereby resolving his delusions?
