Might that be possible?

Wuzhi said: "Heaven has punished him—how can it be undone?" This means his innate capacity is such that he is naturally condemned by Heaven, and it cannot be undone.

Duke Ai of Lu asked Confucius: "In Wei there is an ugly man named Aitai Tuo." *Shiwen* commentary: "*E*: meaning ugly. Li [Yi] comments: '*Aitai*: an ugly appearance. *Tuo* is his given name.'" "When men dwell with him, they grow so attached that they cannot bear to leave."

Women who saw him would beg their parents, saying, "I would rather be this man’s concubine than another’s wife," and this happened more than ten times without cease. He never initiated speech, only responding in harmony. He never took the lead—only after sensing another’s intent did he reply. Without the rank of a ruler, he saved others from mortal peril. Without amassing wealth, he filled others’ bellies. Though his ugliness startled the world, he did not move people with beauty. Harmonious yet never leading, he never drew others to himself. His knowledge did not extend beyond his immediate domain, nor did his fame reach far beyond his borders. Yet men and women alike gathered before him. This must be because he possessed something beyond ordinary men.

I summoned him to observe him, and indeed, his ugliness startled the world. After less than a month in my company, I began to sense the depth of his character. Before a year had passed, I trusted him completely. When the state had no minister, I offered him the governance of the realm. He responded only after long hesitation, as if reluctant, and his words seemed to drift away, untethered to any intent. I felt ashamed, yet in the end, I entrusted him with the state. Soon after, he left me and departed. I grieved as if I had lost something, as if no one remained to share the joy of ruling this land. What manner of man is he?"

Confucius said: "I once traveled to Chu and saw young pigs nursing from their dead mother. After a moment, they glanced around in alarm and all abandoned her to flee. They were startled because she no longer saw them, no longer bore the likeness of the living. What they loved in their mother was not her form, but what animated her form. When warriors die in battle, their burials do not include the ceremonial *sha* ornaments, for these are the trappings of martial prowess, and the dead possess no such prowess. When a man’s feet are severed, his shoes are not cherished, for they are made for feet, and he has none. In neither case does the root remain.

The palace women who serve the Son of Heaven do not cut their nails or pierce their ears; those who take wives remain outside the household, not subject to further service. Preserving the body’s wholeness is sufficient to inspire such care—how much more so for one whose virtue is complete! Now, Aitai Tuo gains trust without speaking, draws affection without merit, and is offered a kingdom with such urgency that one fears he might refuse. This must be because his talents are whole and his virtue unmanifest."

Duke Ai said: "What do you mean by *talents being whole*?"

Confucius said: "Life and death, survival and ruin, poverty and wealth, worthiness and unworthiness, slander and praise, hunger and thirst, cold and heat—these are the changes of affairs, the movements of fate. They alternate before us day and night, yet knowledge cannot trace their beginnings. Thus, they are not enough to disturb one’s harmony or enter the mind’s sanctuary. One should allow them to flow smoothly without losing joy, ensuring that day and night, without interruption, one’s presence brings the vitality of spring to all things."

"Xi (郄) means interval." Xuan comments: "Let the qi of harmony and ease flow unimpeded, never losing one’s joyful nature, so that day and night, without a moment’s interruption, one moves with all things in the springtime of harmony." This is to continuously generate seasons within the heart. Xuan comments: "Here, the four seasons do not reside in heaven and earth, but the spring within one’s heart knows no interruption—thus seasons arise in unbroken succession within the heart." This is called *talents being whole*.

"What do you mean by *virtue being unmanifest*?"
Confucius said: "Levelness is the fullness of still water. Guo comments: 'Nothing in the world is as level as still water.' It may serve as a standard, for Guo comments: 'Utterly without bias, it becomes the world’s measure of correctness.' One inwardly preserves it while outwardly remaining undisturbed. *Disturbed* means moved. One inwardly preserves clarity without being moved by external things. Virtue is the cultivation of perfect harmony. Xuan comments: 'When the cultivation of the Great Harmony is complete, it is called virtue.' *Virtue being unmanifest* means that things cannot depart from it."

One whose inner power is profound is delightful for others to draw near. Another day, Duke Ai told Min Zi what he had heard: "At first, I believed that facing south to rule the world, holding the people’s guiding principles, and fearing their deaths—Cheng comments: 'Holding fast to the guiding cords, anxious for the multitudes, providing food and instruction, dreading their early demise'—was the height of understanding. Now, having heard the words of the perfected person," Xuan comments: "the words of Confucius about Aitai Tuo,"

"For I fear I lack its reality and, by lightly using my person, may lose my state. Between Confucius and myself, there is no ruler-minister relation—only a bond of virtue as friends."

---

Yin Qi, Zhi Li, and Wu Chun—Cheng comments: "Yin means crooked; he walks with twisted limbs and raised heels. Chun means lips; his body is cracked and broken, hunchbacked and maimed, and he also lacks lips." *Shiwen* notes: "Chun: Xu reads *shi zhěn*; also pronounced *chún*."

He spoke to Duke Ling of Wei, who took such delight in him that when he looked upon whole men, their necks seemed pitifully frail. *Shuo* (upper) reads *shuì* for "delight"; *yue* (lower) is pronounced *yuè*. The same applies below. *Shiwen* notes: "Dòu means neck. Li comments: 'Shoulder-shoulder describes a meager, slight appearance.'" Li Zhen comments: "In the *Kaogong Ji* of the *Rites of Zhou*, the phrase 'counting eyes and *kuān* necks' is glossed: '*Kuān* means a long neck.' This matches the sense of *shoulder-shoulder*, showing that *shoulder* is a loan character and the original graph should be *kuān*."

When Weng Àng with his great goiter spoke to Duke Huan of Qi, the duke took such delight in him that when he looked upon whole men, their necks seemed pitifully frail. *Shuowen* notes: "Àng means tumor." Li comments: "Weng Àng describes a large goiter."

Thus, where virtue excels, form is forgotten—a summation of the above.

One who fails to forget what ought to be forgotten, yet forgets what ought not to be forgotten—this is called true forgetting. Form should be forgotten, virtue should not be forgotten; to do the opposite is genuine oblivion. Thus the sage wanders where he will, letting his mind roam in emptiness. Yet knowledge becomes a snare: the movement of wisdom and cunning spawns offshoots and entanglements. Constraint becomes glue: rites and trust bind and fasten. Virtue becomes a bond: the broad cultivation of virtuous intent creates mutual attachment. Craft becomes commerce: skillful transformation and accumulation facilitate trade.

The sage does not scheme—what use is knowledge? With no plans in mind, why employ wit? He does not carve—what use is glue? Unhewn substance needs no constraint. He suffers no loss—what use is virtue? Virtue means attainment; with nothing lost, why summon others through virtue? He does not traffic—what use is commerce? Valuing no goods, he has no need for trade. These four are the Heavenly Provisions. The Heavenly Provisions are Heaven’s sustenance. *Shiwen* notes: “Provisions means nourishment.” Knowledge, constraint, virtue, and craft—these are how Heaven nourishes man. Heaven’s nourishment is how Heaven feeds him. Having received sustenance from Heaven, why employ man? Having received Heaven’s sustenance, one should preserve one’s natural state, not adulterate it with human artifice.

He has the form of a man, but not the passions of a man—banishing all emotion. Having the form of a man, he mingles with men; *Cheng* comments: “Blending light and merging traces.” Lacking the passions of a man, right and wrong find no hold in him—cutting off the roots of right and wrong. How tiny and slight! This is how he belongs to man. How towering and vast! This is how he completes his Heaven. *Cui* comments: “Resembling men makes him small; aligning with Heaven makes him great.” *Cheng* comments: “Towering, a description of great height.”

Huizi said to Zhuangzi: “Do men truly have no passions?”
Zhuangzi said: “Yes.”
Huizi said: “If a man has no passions, how can he be called a man?”
Zhuangzi said: “The Dao gives him his appearance, Heaven gives him his form. *Cheng* comments: ‘The Dao of emptiness and penetration shapes his countenance; the principle of spontaneity endows his form.’ How can he not be called a man?”
Huizi said: “If he is called a man, how can he have no passions?”
Zhuangzi said: “This is not what I mean by passions. *Xuan* comments: ‘Huizi first misconstrues the word *passions*.’ *Case*: Guo takes *this* to refer to right and wrong, which is incorrect. What I mean by having no passions is that a man does not allow likes and dislikes to inwardly harm his person, but constantly follows nature without embellishing life.” *Xuan* comments: “He adheres to the principle of life without adding human artifice.”
Huizi said: “If one does not embellish life, how can one have a body?” *Cheng* comments: “If one does not rely on the way of embellishing life, how can one have a body?”
Zhuangzi said: “The Dao gives him his appearance, Heaven gives him his form—he does not allow likes and dislikes to inwardly harm his person. This is how he has a body. Now you estrange your spirit, exhaust your essence, lean against a tree to chant, and rest on a withered catalpa to doze. *Cheng* comments: ‘Withered catalpa’ refers to a knee-high desk. Huizi estranges his spirit and wearies his essence, so when he walks he leans against a tree to chant, and when he sits he rests on a desk to discourse, his body weary and his mind spent, fatigued and dozing.” Heaven chose your form—*chose* is to be understood as in Mencius’ “Heaven chose and sent you.” Yet you clamor about “hard and white”! How is this different from Gongsun Long’s “hard and white” argument? It is what the *Qi Wu Lun* calls “ending in the obscurity of hard and white.” For the explanation, see above. *Inner Chapters, “The Great and Venerable Teacher,” Chapter Six.*

The present passage states: “People still imitate him.” *Imitate* means to take as teacher. It also says: “My teacher! My teacher!”—taking the Dao as teacher. *Zong* means “master.” To know what Heaven does and to know what man does is to reach the ultimate. To know what Heaven does is to know that it gives life: all things arise spontaneously, so one should accord with their spontaneity. To know what man does is to use what his knowledge knows to nourish what his knowledge does not know, so that he completes his Heaven-appointed years without dying prematurely—this is the fullness of knowledge. *Liang qi zhi* is read as *zhi* (knowledge). If one does not force knowledge, then knowledge is properly nourished. Guo comments: “What man does has its limits, so one should follow without forcing; what man knows has its bounds, so one should use without excess. Thus, what one knows does not become self-entangling through boundlessness.” Even so, there is trouble. Cheng comments: “Though knowledge is full and fine, it still brings trouble and encumbrance—it is not as good as forgetting knowledge and following the solitary.” For knowledge must await something before it is fitting, and what it awaits is especially uncertain. Cheng comments: “Knowledge must face an object; without an object, it is not fitting. Since objects arise and vanish unpredictably, knowledge too is contingent and inconstant. Only when both object and knowledge are forgotten can trouble be avoided.” How do we know that what I call Heaven is not man? That what I call man is not Heaven? Cheng comments: “Knowledge can be applied, but all is natural. Thus, Heaven and man are one in principle, so that to call Heaven is to call man, and to call man is to call Heaven. *What I call* refers to Zhuangzi’s self-reference. Here, Heaven and man are merged, self and things made one.” Only when there is a true person is there true knowledge. Guo comments: “Only when there is a true person do all the knowledges under Heaven attain their truth and cannot be confounded.” What is a true person? The true persons of old did not oppose the few—with empty mind, they accepted things, and though few, they did not resist. They did not boast of success, did not take achievement as a cause for pride. They did not scheme for scholars. Cheng comments: “With emptiness and equanimity, scholars naturally gather—no scheming is needed to attract them.” Such persons, when they err, do not regret; when they are right, do not feel self-satisfied. Cheng comments: “When the time has passed, they have no regret or remorse; when their allotted fate happens to be fitting, they do not take it as a personal virtue.” Such persons ascend heights without trembling, enter water without getting wet, enter fire without feeling heat. Their knowledge’s ability to ascend and lodge in the Dao is like this. They do not take peril, difficulty, life, or death to heart. Their ability to ascend to the Dao is not what the world’s knowledge can comprehend. The true persons of old slept without dreaming. Cheng comments: “They cut off thought, so their sleep was still and empty.” When awake, they had no cares. Guo comments: “They were at ease wherever they dwelled.” They did not relish their food. Cheng comments: “They did not indulge in flavors.” Their breathing was deep. Li comments: “The appearance of inner breathing.” The true person’s breathing reaches the heels. Cheng comments: “*Heels* means the soles of the feet.” Xuan comments: “Breath circulates to the *yongquan* point.” The breathing of ordinary people reaches only the throat. Xuan comments: “It stops at the juncture of the epiglottis.” Those who are overborne in debate have their throats choked as if retching. *Overborne* means being bested in argument. *Throat* refers to the larynx. *Choked* is before the voice comes out; *words* are after the voice comes out. In the moment of swallowing and emitting, it is as if retching—this describes those without cultivation. The deeper their desires, the shallower their Heavenly mechanism. With deep passions and desires, their spirit-mechanism is dull and sluggish. The true persons of old did not delight in life, did not hate death. Guo comments: “They took transformation as their body.” They did not rejoice at going out, did not resist coming in. *Shiwen* notes: “*Resist* is also written as *reject*. Li comments: ‘Rejoicing at going out means scheming for life; resisting coming in means hating death.’” They went *xiaoran*, they came *xiaoran*—that is all. Cheng comments: “*Xiaoran* means unentangled.” They did not forget their beginning, did not seek their end. Xuan comments: “They knew the source of life and entrusted the return of death.” They received and rejoiced in it. Xuan comments: “After receiving life, they were always content.” They forgot and returned to it. Xuan comments: “They forgot death and returned to Heaven.” This is called not using the mind to cast aside the Dao, not using man to assist Heaven. This is called the true person. Guo comments: “The things that move people are endless; the desires that people pursue are boundless—then the Heavenly principle is extinguished. The true person knows that using the mind goes against the Dao, and assisting Heaven harms life, so he does not do so.” Yu comments: “According to Guo’s commentary, *cast aside* is likely a mistake for *go against*.” Such persons’ minds were forgetful. Xuan comments: “*Minds* should be *forgetfulness*. No thought.” Their countenance was still. Xuan comments: “No action.” Their foreheads were broad. Xuan comments: “*Foreheads* means the brow.” *Broad* describes great simplicity. Xuan comments: “Read *hui* with the rising tone.” They were bleak like autumn, warm like spring. Guo comments: “Killing things is not for might; giving life is not for benevolence.” Their joy and anger corresponded to the four seasons. Xuan comments: “Joy and anger were without intention, like the movements of the four seasons.” They were fitting with things, yet none knew their limit. They were fitting with circumstances, yet none could fathom their bounds.

Thus, when the sage employs military force, he overthrows states yet does not lose the people’s hearts. Cui comments: “Overthrowing enemy states while winning their people’s hearts.” His beneficence and favor extend to the myriad things, yet not out of love for others. He acts through benevolence and righteousness, yet does not practice benevolence and righteousness. Therefore, one who delights in connecting with things is no sage; one who does not seek to connect with things, yet finds the nature of things naturally aligned, is a sage. Having partiality is not benevolence; perfect benevolence has no private attachments. Following the seasons is not wisdom; Xuan comments: “Choosing the time to act reveals a mind calculating success and failure.” One who does not comprehend the interconnection of benefit and harm is no gentleman; failing to observe their interconnection leads to pursuit and avoidance. Acting for fame at the cost of oneself is no scholar; Cheng comments: “One who seeks fame through action yet loses their true nature is no man of the Dao.” Sacrificing oneself without authenticity is not mastery over others; Xuan comments: “Merely casting aside one’s life without aligning with true nature is to be used by the world, not to use others.” Such were Hu Bujie—Cheng comments: “Surnamed Hu, styled Bujie, a worthy of Yao’s time who refused Yao’s abdication and drowned himself in the river”—Wu Guang—Cheng comments: “A man of the Xia era who cultivated life through herbs, loved playing the zither, and when Tang offered him the throne, refused and drowned himself with a stone in the Lu River”—Boyi and Shuqi, Jizi Xuyu—Sima comments: “Xuyu was Jizi’s name. The *Shizi* states: ‘Jizi Xuyu painted his body to feign leprosy and let his hair loose to feign madness’”—Ji Ta—Cheng comments: “A recluse of Tang’s time who, hearing Tang’s offer to Wu Guang, feared it might extend to himself, led his disciples into the Kuǎn River and drowned”—and Shentu Di—*Shiwen* notes: “A man of the Yin era who drowned himself in the river with a stone.” These were men used by others, following others’ paths, yet never attaining their own true way. Guo comments: “All abandoned themselves to imitate others, harming themselves for others’ sake.” Xuan comments: “Being used by others, pleasing others—what benefit is there to one’s true nature?”

The true person of old had an appearance lofty yet not crumbling. Guo comments: “Accordant with things yet not forming cliques.” Yu comments: “Guo’s note is incorrect. This describes their appearance, not their virtue. *Yi* should be read as *e* (峨). In the *Dao of Heaven* chapter, ‘their appearance *yiran* (义然)’ means *eran* (峨然, towering). *Peng* should be read as *beng* (崩). The *Yijing* says ‘*peng* comes without blame,’ and the *Han Shu Wuxing Zhi* quotes it as ‘*beng* comes without blame.’ *Yi er bu peng* means their appearance was towering and grand yet not crumbling.” They seemed insufficient yet did not submit. Xuan comments: “Humble in self-cultivation yet not subservient to others.” They were solitary yet not rigid. Wang comments: “*Gu* means standing alone, unaligned with the crowd.” Cui comments: “*Gu* means angular.” Li Zhen comments: “*Gu* is a loan for *gu* (孤). The *Er Ya*’s ‘*gu* bamboo’ has a *Shiwen* note: ‘Originally written as *gu* (孤).’ This confirms the interchangeability of *gu* and *gu*. The solitary and independent have edges and corners, hence the character is also borrowed as *gu*. ‘*Yu hu qi gu*’ parallels ‘*zhang hu qi xu*,’ where *yu* should be a loan for *●*. The *Shuowen* states: ‘*●* means walking calmly.’” Note: *Bu jian* means not obstinate. They were expansive in their emptiness yet not ostentatious. Cheng comments: “*Zhang* means vast.” Note: Vast and clear in their emptiness, yet not superficial. They were radiant as if joyful! Xiang comments: “*Bingbing* is the appearance of joy.” Guo comments: “The perfected person has no joy, yet is harmoniously at ease, thus seeming joyful.” They moved as if compelled! Xiang comments: “*Cui* is the appearance of motion.” Cheng comments: “Moved only by necessity, never initiating, thus acting only when unavoidable.” They gathered and enhanced my countenance. Jianwen comments: “*Chu* means to gather.” Xuan comments: “Like water gathering to form a lustrous sheen, their harmonious and gracious demeanor made them approachable.” They drew me in with their virtue. *Yu* conveys the sense of connection. Xuan comments: “Their tranquil and magnanimous virtue made me wish to dwell with them.” They were vast as if embracing the world! Cui’s edition has *guang* (广) for *li* (厉), which should be followed. Yu comments: “*Shi* is a loan for *tai* (泰). *Guang* and *tai* correspond in meaning.” Guo Qingfan comments: “*Li* and *guang* were anciently interchangeable. *Tai* was written as *da* (大). *Shi* and *da* were also interchangeable.” They were towering and beyond control. Cheng comments: “Lofty and distant, transcending the world, they could not be restrained.” They were continuous as if fond of seclusion. Li comments: “*Lian* means the appearance of continuity.” Guo comments: “Vast and remote, their gate unseen.” Cheng comments: “Silent as if sealed, their presence unheard.” *Shiwen* notes: “*Hao* is read as *hu bao* (呼报).” They were absent-minded, forgetting their words. *Shiwen* notes: “*Men* is read as *wang ben* (忘本).” Cheng comments: “*Men* is the appearance of absent-mindedness. The above describes the true person’s virtue; below clarifies their way of governing and benefiting things.”

They took punishment as their foundation. Guo comments: “Punishment is the foundation of governance, not of their own making.” They took ritual as their wings. Guo comments: “Ritual is how the world conducts itself, not of their own creation.” They took knowledge as their timing. Guo comments: “Knowledge moves with the times, not of their own initiation.” They took virtue as their guide. Guo comments: “Virtue is what others follow, not of their own design.” Taking punishment as their foundation, they were lenient in execution. Guo comments: “Entrusting governance to the natural course of execution, thus even in killing, they were magnanimous.”

Taking ritual as their wings, they thereby moved through the world.
Guo comments: “Following what the world already practices, thus nothing was unachievable.”

Taking knowledge as their timing, they acted only when compelled by affairs.
Knowledge responded to the moment, compelled by worldly matters, adapting as circumstances required.

Taking virtue as their guide, it was said that they walked with those who had feet to the hill.
Xuan comments: “Where virtue resides, all may reach it; I merely followed. Like a hill that all with feet may climb, I simply ascended with them—no distinction was made.”
Note: Acting without intent to act, they arrived naturally, thus “walked with those who had feet.”

Yet people truly believed them to be diligent in conduct.
Xuan comments: “People saw the true person as tirelessly active, unaware that not even the slightest part was of their own doing.”

Thus, whether they were loved or not loved, it was all the same.
Cheng comments: “Having forgotten attachment to beauty or ugliness, they also cast aside preferences of love and hate. Thus, whether loved or not loved, it arose from the sentiments of the common, while the sage’s emptiness remained undivided.”

Whether unified or not unified, it was all the same.
Cheng comments: “The unified belongs to the sage; the not unified, to the common. Yet sage and common are not two, thus the not unified is also unified.”

In their unity, they walked with Heaven.
In their disunity, they walked with humanity.
Cheng comments: “Harmonizing with Heaven, equalizing the myriad things—they were of Heaven’s kind. Distinctions of self and other—they walked with humanity.”

That Heaven and humanity do not overcome one another—this is called the true person.
Cheng comments: “Though Heaven has no self or other, and humanity has right and wrong, when clearly illuminated, all returns to emptiness. If Heaven were to overcome and humanity to be inferior, how could they be equal? This is the merging of Heaven and humanity, the obscuring of victory and defeat. One who embodies this is called the true person.”

Life and death are fate; their constancy is like night and day—this is Heaven.
What humanity cannot partake in belongs to the nature of things.
Life and death are like night and day, all determined by Heavenly fate, beyond human intervention. This is the nature of things, truly nothing to cling to.

They regarded Heaven as their father and cherished it—how much more that which stands transcendent!
If one cherishes Heaven as their body does, how much more that which rises transcendent from Heaven!

People consider having a lord superior to themselves.
Xuan comments: “The lord’s position surpasses their own.”
And yet they would die for him.
Xuan comments: “They offer loyalty.”
How much more that which is true!
If one cherishes their lord as their body does, how much more that which is truly aligned with him!

When springs run dry, fish find themselves stranded on land, moistening one another with breath, bedewing one another with foam—better far to forget each other in rivers and lakes. This illustrates how clinging to life and fearing death is inferior to forgetting both in the natural Way.

Rather than praising Yao and condemning Jie, better to forget both and be transformed by the Dao. Xuan comments: “Here *Dao* is used lightly, referring to the way of right and wrong. To praise Yao and condemn Jie is not as good as forgetting both ways; to love life and hate death is not as good as forgetting both burdens.”

For the great clod bears me with form, wearies me with life, eases me with old age, and rests me with death. Thus, to treat my life well is to treat my death well. Xuan comments: “To follow nature purely is to treat my life well. If so, death will not be bitter.” This passage appears again later. As the *Liezi*’s “Heaven’s Auspices” chapter says: “People all know the joy of life but not its suffering; they know the weariness of old age but not its ease; they know the dread of death but not its rest.”

Hiding a boat in a ravine or a mountain in a marsh seems secure—yet in the dead of night, a strong man may carry them away, and the unaware would not know. A boat can be carried off, a mountain moved. Xuan comments: “Creation works in silence, yet those who hide things still believe they remain in their original place.” Whether hiding something large or small, there is always a way for it to escape. But if one hides the world within the world, there is no escape—this is the great truth of all things. Whether hiding something large or small, each has its proper place, yet there is no principle that does not change. Xuan comments: “Escape arises from excessive attachment to hiding. But if one awakens to the principle that the world cannot be privately possessed and instead entrusts it to the world, then this principle will be everywhere shared with me—how could there be any escape? This is the true nature of things.”

Yet people delight in possessing human form—if those with human form are but one of ten thousand transformations without end, how boundless their joy must be! *Fan* (犯) is interchangeable with *fan* (笵). Seeing the form of humanity, they rejoice—if human life is endless, who would not delight in their own existence?

Thus, the sage wanders where nothing can escape and all things abide. Xuan comments: “The sage embodies creation entirely; though form has life and death, this principle already flows with heaven and earth—thus it is said that all things abide.”

Excelling in youth and age, in beginnings and ends, people still emulate them—how much more that on which the myriad things depend and by which all transformations await! The *Shiwen* notes: “*Yao* (妖) is also written as *yao* (夭).” Cheng comments: “Whether long-lived or short-lived, young or old, they remain unconcerned. Though they may not forget life and death, they harbor no aversion—this alone is enough to serve as a teacher for others. How much more when one merges with the myriad things, darkly uniting with transformation, becoming the master of all things!”

The Dao has essence and trustworthiness, yet it is without action or form. Xuan comments: “Essence is the movement of stillness; trustworthiness is the sign of movement.” Cheng comments: “Serene and silent, it is without action; invisible, it is without form.”

It can be transmitted but not received. Guo comments: “It has been passed down through the ages as a dwelling, yet none can receive and possess it.” It can be attained but not seen. Cheng comments: “Through solitary awakening in the heart, it can be attained—but separated from form and color, it cannot be seen.”

It is self-originating, self-rooted. Xuan comments: “The Dao is the root of all things, and there is nothing that serves as its own root—it is self-originating, self-rooted.” Before heaven and earth existed, it was already eternally present. Cheng comments: “As Laozi says: ‘There is a thing formed in chaos, born before heaven and earth.’”

It spiritually empowers ghosts and gods, emperors and lords. Below, Kanpi and Feng Yi are ghosts; Xiwei and Fuxi are emperors—all are spiritually empowered by the Dao. It gives birth to heaven and earth. Cheng comments: “As Laozi says: ‘Heaven attains the One and becomes clear; earth attains the One and becomes stable.’”

It exists before the Supreme Ultimate yet is not high; it exists below the Six Directions yet is not deep. Before yin and yang divided, this was called the Supreme Ultimate. Heaven, earth, and the four directions are called the Six Directions. Cheng comments: “The Dao exists before the Supreme Ultimate yet is not lofty; it exists below the Six Directions yet is not profound.”

It precedes heaven and earth yet is not ancient; it is older than antiquity yet is not aged. The *Shiwen* notes: “*Zhang* (长) is read as *dingzhang*.” This passage appears again later.

The Lord of Shivei attained it and thereby harnessed heaven and earth. Shivei is the same as Shivei, an ancient emperor. Cheng comments: “Harness (*qie*) is also written as *qi* (契). It means he could unify the myriad things and align them with the Two Modes.”

Fuxi attained it and thereby merged with the Mother of Qi. Cheng comments: “Merged (*xi*) means united. The Mother of Qi is the primordial qi’s source. Having attained the supreme Dao, he was thus able to draw the Eight Trigrams, unfold the six lines, harmonize yin and yang, and unite with the primordial qi.”

The Weaving Dipper attained it and has endured through all ages without error. Cheng comments: “The Northern Dipper serves as the guiding cord for all stars, hence called the Weaving Dipper. Having attained the supreme Dao, it thus sustains heaven and earth, cycling through beginnings and ends without deviation.”

The sun and moon attained it and have endured through all ages without ceasing.

Kanhuai attained it and thereby entered Kunlun. The *Shiwen* notes: “Cui writes *huai* as 邳. Sima comments: ‘Kanhuai is the name of a spirit, with a human face and beast’s form.’ The *Huainanzi* writes it as *Qinfu*.” Cheng comments: “Kunlun is the name of a mountain spirit. Entered (*xi*) means penetrated.”

Feng Yi attained it and thereby roamed the great rivers. Sima comments: “The *Qingling Zhuan* states: ‘Feng Yi was a man from Dishou in Tongxiang, Huayin (Cheng’s commentary includes the character *li* [里]). He ingested eight minerals, attained the immortality of water, and became the River Earl.’”

One variant states: “On the gengzi day of the eighth month, he bathed in the river and drowned.”

Jianwu attained it and thereby dwelt upon the great mountains. Sima comments: “A mountain spirit, undying, who lived until the time of Confucius.” Cheng comments: “Having attained the Dao, he dwelt on Mount Tai, becoming the spirit of Mount Tai.”

The Yellow Emperor attained it and thereby ascended to the cloudy heavens. Cui comments: “The Yellow Emperor attained the Dao and ascended to heaven.”

Zhuanxu attained it and thereby resided in the Dark Palace. Li comments: “Zhuanxu was Gaoyang. The Dark Palace is the palace of the north. The *Monthly Ordinances* states: ‘Its sovereign is Zhuanxu; its spirit is Xuanming.’” Cheng comments: “Having attained the Dao, he became the emperor of the north. *Xuan* is the color of the north, hence he resided in the Dark Palace.”

Yuqiong attained it and stood at the North Pole. *Shiwen* notes: “The *Classic of Mountains and Seas* states: ‘Yuqiong of the north, black-bodied with human hands and feet, rides two dragons.’ Guo Pu takes him as a water spirit, with a human face and bird’s body. Jianwen comments: ‘The spirit of the Northern Sea, also named Yujing, was the grandson of the Yellow Emperor.’”

The Queen Mother of the West attained it and sat upon Shaoguang. None knew her beginning; none knew her end. *Shiwen* notes: “The *Classic of Mountains and Seas* states: ‘The Queen Mother of the West resembles a human, with a dog’s tail, disheveled hair, and a sheng headdress, skilled in whistling, dwelling at the edge of the sea.’ The *Inner Biography of Emperor Wu of Han* states: ‘The Queen Mother of the West and the Lady of the Upper Prime descended to the emperor, beautiful in countenance, divine immortals.’ Cui comments: ‘Shaoguang is the name of a mountain.’ Another says: It is the name of the empty realm of the west.”

Pengzu attained it, reaching from the time of Youyu down to the Five Hegemons. Cui comments: “Pengzu lived seven hundred years; some consider him an immortal, undying.” Cheng comments: “From the time of Youyu down to Yin and Zhou, he lived eight hundred years in all.”

Fuyue attained it and thereby assisted King Wu Ding, governing the realm. He rode the Eastern Ridge, mounted the Tail of the Winnowing Basket, and ranked among the constellations. Sima comments: “The Eastern Ridge lies between the Winnowing Basket and the Dipper, the eastern ridge of the Celestial Ford. The *Star Canon* states: ‘The star Fuyue lies above the Tail.’” Cui comments: “When Fuyue died, his spirit rode the Eastern Ridge and lodged in the Dragon’s Tail, becoming a constellation.” *Shiwen* notes: “Cui’s edition includes below: ‘Born without parents, at death he ascended in假, and after three years his form vanished—this speaks of the spirit that cannot be named.’” The text below cites seven instances to illustrate this.

Nanbo Zikui asked Yu Yu: “You are advanced in years, yet your complexion is like that of a child. How is this?” Li comments: “Kui should be Qi, a phonetic error.” *Shiwen* notes: “Yu is pronounced like Yu. One says: She is a woman.” Yu Yu replied: “I have heard the Dao.” Nanbo Zikui said: “Can the Dao be learned?” He replied: “Alas! How could it be?”

“You are not the right person for this. Now, there was one named Buliang Yi who possessed the talent of a sage but not the Dao of a sage. I possess the Dao of a sage but not the talent of a sage.” Li comments: “Buliang is the surname, Yi the given name.” Xuan remarks: “Yi was keen of intellect, like Zigong; Yu, forgetful of intellect, like Yan Hui.” “I wished to teach him, hoping that he might indeed become a sage! If not, to impart the Dao of a sage to one with the talent of a sage would have been easy. Yet I persisted in teaching him, holding fast without departing, repeating my instructions again and again. After three days, he was able to put the world outside himself.” Cheng comments: “With the heart emptied and still, all things under heaven become void.” “Once he had put the world outside himself, I persisted further. After seven days, he was able to put things outside himself.” Guo comments: “Things are what one needs morning and night, intimately tied and hard to forget.” Cheng comments: “The world is distant and easily forgotten; the things that sustain the body are near and hard to forget. After seven days of steadfast practice, he was able to leave them behind.” “Once he had put things outside himself, I persisted further. After nine days, he was able to put life outside himself.” Cheng comments: “Body and form dissolved, self forgotten in seated oblivion.” “Once he had put life outside himself, he was then able to attain morning clarity.” Cheng comments: “Viewing death and life as one, forgetting both self and things, suddenly like the dawn breaking—hence ‘morning clarity.’” Xuan remarks: “Morning clarity is like the clarity of early dawn.” “With morning clarity, he was then able to see the solitary.” “To see the One alone.” “Having seen the solitary, he was then able to transcend past and present.” Cheng comments: “Following the daily renewal of creation, moving with change and transformation, thus transcending past and present.” “Having transcended past and present, he was then able to enter into neither-death-nor-life.” Xuan remarks: “Life and death are one. At this point, the Dao is within oneself.” “That which kills life does not die; that which gives life to life does not live.” Su Yu comments: “These two lines elucidate the preceding. Abandoning the delusion of clinging to life is called ‘killing life’; abiding in the spontaneity of nature is called ‘giving life to life.’ Accepting death and life as they come is neither death nor life.” “As for the Dao, in its relation to things, there is nothing it does not escort, nothing it does not welcome.” Cheng comments: “To escort is to send off. The Dao as a thing aids without limit, welcoming endless life and sending off boundless death.” “There is nothing it does not destroy, nothing it does not complete.” Cheng comments: “Without sending, yet sending—nothing is not destroyed; without welcoming, yet welcoming—nothing is not completed.” “Its name is called ‘tranquil amidst entanglement.’” “‘Tranquil amidst entanglement’ means that only after entanglement does one attain completion.” Guo Songtao comments: “Mencius’ Zhao commentary: ‘Entanglement means pressing.’ The perceptions of self and things, life and death press within; the mechanisms of welcoming and sending, completion and destruction press without. Yet one remains unmoved in heart—this is called ‘tranquil amidst entanglement.’ Placing oneself amidst the flourishing changes, conflicts, and clashes of the world, yet with a heart fundamentally at peace, one approaches completion—hence ‘only after entanglement does one attain completion.’”

Nanbo Zikui said: “From whom did you alone hear this?”
He replied: “I heard it from the son of the scribe’s ink.”
Cheng comments: “‘Vice’ means secondary.”
Xuan remarks: “Writing is done with brush and ink, yet writing is not the Dao—it is but an aid to transmitting the Dao. Hence it is called ‘the scribe’s ink.’ Moreover, compared to the original writing, later texts are its progeny, hence ‘the son of the scribe’s ink.’”
“The son of the scribe’s ink heard it from the grandson of recitation.”
Cheng comments: “Reciting it in a connected manner.”
Note: This refers to repeated reading, as in “connecting and reciting.” *Luo* and *luo* are homophonous loan characters. Compared to the ancient practice of reading books, hence “the grandson of recitation.” Ancient books were first transmitted orally and later inscribed on bamboo and silk, hence the sequence.
“The grandson of recitation heard it from Illuminating Vision.”
“Illuminating Vision heard it from Whispered Assent.”
“Whispered Assent” refers to soft speech, like murmuring.
“Whispered Assent heard it from Diligent Practice.”
Cheng comments: “‘Diligent’ means ‘must’; ‘practice’ means ‘to act.’ One must act diligently without slackening.”
“Diligent Practice heard it from Chanting.”
The *Shiwen* reads: “Yu is pronounced like *wu*. Wang comments: ‘Chanting refers to songs and ballads.’”
Xuan remarks: “Through chanting and singing, one deeply lodges the meaning.”
“Chanting heard it from Dark Obscurity.”
Xuan remarks: “Dark Obscurity is a place of stillness and silence.”
“Dark Obscurity heard it from Profound Solitude.”
Xuan remarks: “Profoundly realizing emptiness and void.”
“Profound Solitude heard it from Doubting the Beginning.”
Xuan remarks: “Reaching the point of no trace, one hears the Dao. ‘Doubting the Beginning’ means that it seems to have a beginning yet never truly had one.”

The four—Zisi, Ziyu, Zili, and Zilai—spoke among themselves, saying: “Who can take nonbeing as the head, life as the spine, and death as the tailbone? Who understands that life, death, existence, and extinction are of one body? I would befriend such a one.” Cheng comments: “Humans arise from emptiness, thus nonbeing is the head. From nonbeing comes being, and life follows, thus life is the spine. Death comes last, thus it is the tailbone. Life and death, though distinct, are of one body. One who grasps this truth finds all encounters fitting; how could there be joy or aversion between existence and extinction? Whoever understands this, I would befriend.”

The four looked at one another and smiled, their hearts in perfect accord, and so they became friends.

Soon after, Ziyu fell ill, and Zisi went to visit him. He exclaimed: “How magnificent!”

The Maker of Things would fashion me into this cramped form!" Cheng comments: "Ziyu laments his own condition." Sima explains: "Cramped—his body is contorted and contracted." His spine was bent, his back hunched, his five vital tubes protruding upward—these were the five visceral conduits turned outward. His chin sank into his navel, his shoulders rose above his crown, and his nape pointed heavenward. Li explains: "His nape, like an excrescence, jutted upward." The qi of yin and yang had fallen into disorder. Yet his heart remained at ease, untroubled. He hobbled swiftly to the well and gazed into it, saying: "Alas! Would the Maker of Things fashion me into this cramped form again!" He sighed once more.

Zisi asked: "Do you resent this?"
Ziyu replied: "No, not at all. Why should I resent it? If gradually my left arm were transformed into a rooster, I would use it to mark the watches of the night. If my right arm were transformed into a crossbow pellet, I would use it to seek roasted owl. If my tailbone were transformed into a wheel and my spirit into a horse, I would ride it—why would I need another steed?" Guo comments: "There is no going forth without cause, and no cause that cannot be followed." Moreover, to gain is to meet one’s time, and to lose is to follow the natural course. Abiding in one’s time and following the natural course, sorrow and joy cannot enter. This is what the ancients called "suspended release." Cheng comments: "To gain is to live; to lose is to die." As in the *Yangsheng zhu* chapter: "To come is to meet one’s time; to depart is to follow the natural course. Abiding in one’s time and following the natural course, sorrow and joy cannot enter. The ancients called this the release of the bound by the Sovereign." This passage confirms it. Yet one who cannot release themselves is bound by things. Guo comments: "If one cannot release themselves, the myriad things will bind them together." Moreover, things have long been unable to overcome Heaven—why should I resent it?"

Soon after, Zilai fell ill, gasping as he neared death. His wife and children gathered around him, weeping. Cheng comments: "Gasping—his breath was labored." Zili went to visit him and shouted: "Begone!"

"Shoo! Make his wife and children leave. Do not be startled by transformation!" *Shiwen* notes: "怛 means 'to startle.'" Do not be alarmed by the one about to transform.

Leaning against the doorframe, Zili spoke to him: "How wondrous is the Creator! What will it make of you next? What thing will it make you? Where will it send you? Will it make you a rat’s liver? A bug’s arm?" Wang comments: "Taking the most minute and base things."

Zilai said: "To parents, a child must follow their commands, no matter if they send them east, west, south, or north. The yin and yang of the world are no less to a person than their parents. Cheng comments: 'The transformations of yin and yang—how are they any different from one’s two parents?' If they press me toward death and I do not heed them, then I am obstinate. What fault is theirs? They are yin and yang. Obstinate means disobedient. Xuan comments: '近 means to press close.'

"The great clod bears me with form, labors me with life, eases me with old age, and rests me with death. Thus, to treat my life well is to treat my death well." These six phrases also appear in the *Da zongshi* chapter.

"Now, a great smith forging metal—if the metal leaps up and says, 'I must become a *Mo* sword!' the great smith will surely deem it an inauspicious metal. A great smith is a metal-forging artisan. If one, upon assuming human form, says, 'I am human! I am human!' the Creator will surely deem them an inauspicious person. *Fan* is the same as *fan* (mold). To become human by chance, then delight in it and cherish it as something distinct from other things—this is what the Creator will deem inauspicious.

"Now, take heaven and earth as a great furnace and the Creator as a great smith—where could one go and not be acceptable?" Rat’s liver or bug’s arm—what concern are they to one’s thoughts!

Then he fell into a deep sleep and awoke transformed. To become human is to sleep; to depart in length is to awaken.

Zisang Hu, Mengzi Fan, and Ziqin Zhang were three friends who said to one another: "Who can join together in non-joining, act for one another in non-acting?" Cheng comments: "Like the hundred parts of the body, each with its own function, mutually serving one another without intent to join or act—yet the work of mutual aid is accomplished. Thus, to move together in non-joining and befriend in non-acting is the same."

"Who can ascend to heaven and wander in the mists?" Xuan comments: "To rise beyond things." "Who can twist and turn without end?" Li comments: "挠挑 means to meander, to wander in the vast and mysterious." "Who can forget life and have no end or limit?" Xuan comments: "Neither delighting in life nor loathing death."

The three looked at one another and smiled, their hearts in perfect accord, and so they became friends.

After a tranquil moment—Cui comments: "莫然 means settled; 闲 means a brief time"—Zisang Hu died, and his body had not yet been buried. Confucius heard of this and sent Zigong to attend to the funeral rites. Cheng comments: "To provide for the funeral."

Some wove bamboo mats—Li comments: "曲 means silkworm trays"—while others played the zither, harmonizing as they sang: "Alas, Zisang Hu! Alas, Zisang Hu! You have returned to your true nature, while we still remain human, alas!" Cheng comments: "猗 is a harmonizing sound."

Zigong hurried forward and said: "May I ask—is it proper to sing before the corpse?"

The two looked at each other and smiled, saying: "What does he know of the meaning of ritual?" *This* refers to Zigong.

Zigong returned and reported to Confucius: "What sort of men are they? They lack cultivated conduct, disregard their own bodies, sing before the corpse, and show no change in countenance. I have no name for them. What sort of men are they?"

Confucius said: "They wander beyond the bounds, while I wander within them. Cheng comments: '方 means boundary.' Beyond and within do not reach one another, and yet I sent you to offer condolences—how crude of me!

"They are about to become companions to the Creator." Wang Yinzhi comments: "为人 means 'to be a companion.' The *Zhongyong* says, 'The humane are human,' and Zheng’s commentary reads: 'Read as the "mutual companions" in *xiang ren ou*.' The *Gongshi dafu* commentary says: 'At every bow and when facing the stele, they bow as mutual companions.' Thus, *ren* and *ou* share the same meaning. The *Huainanzi*’s *Yuandao* chapter says, 'To be companions to the Creator,' and the *Qisu* chapter says, 'Above, they befriend the divine and enlightened; below, they become companions to the Creator'—this is the clearest proof."

"Yet they wander in the single qi of heaven and earth. They regard life as an excrescence or hanging tumor—Cheng comments: 'Qi gathers to form life, like a tumor or growth attached—nothing to delight in'—and death as the bursting of an abscess. *Shiwen* notes: '● is pronounced *huàn*.' Xuan comments: 'A type of ulcer.' Cheng comments: 'Qi scatters in death, like an abscess bursting—nothing to regret.'

"Those who are like this—how could they know where life and death, before and after, reside?" Xuan comments: "A single qi in circulation."

"Borrowing from different things, entrusting themselves to a shared body—Xuan comments: "As in the *Yuanjue Jing*’s teaching that earth, wind, water, and fire combine to form the body—seeing life as mere chance." Forgetting their liver and gall, disregarding their ears and eyes—Xuan comments: "Disregarding the body, seeing death as mere chance." Reversing and repeating, beginning and ending, not knowing the head or tail, coming and going in life and death, never knowing their limit.

"Vaguely wandering beyond the dust and grime, carefree in the work of non-action." Cheng comments: "芒然 means the appearance of unawareness. To be unrestrained beyond the dust’s burdens, at ease in the realm of clarity and vastness."

The Master could hardly be expected to entangle himself in the world’s conventional rites, merely to display himself before the eyes and ears of the crowd!" Cheng comments: "愦愦 means confusion and disorder." *Shiwen* notes: "观 means to display."

Zigong asked: "Then what path does the Master follow?"
Cheng comments: "Within the bounds of the world or beyond them—we do not know which Way the Master follows."
Confucius replied: "Qiu is one punished by Heaven."
Cheng comments: "Sacred traces and ritual propriety are but shackles upon form and nature. Since the Master remains within the bounds of the world, he follows the principle of nature—yet is one marked by punishment. Thus, in the 'Virtue Complete' chapter, it is said: *'Punished by Heaven, how could he be freed?*'"
"Nevertheless, I share this with you."
Xuan comments: "He does not wish to conceal what he has attained."

Zigong said: "May I ask about this path?"
Confucius replied: "Fish are made for water, just as humans are made for the Dao. 造 means to reach. Fish that reach water find joy; humans that reach the Dao find joy. Those who are made for water dig ponds to sustain themselves; those made for the Dao find tranquility without effort."
*Shiwen* notes: "池 may also be written as 地." Both versions are valid.
"Fish obtain water and are sustained; humans obtain the Dao and their nature is settled. 生 and 性 are interchangeable. Thus it is said: *Fish forget one another in rivers and lakes; humans forget one another in the arts of the Dao.*"
Xuan comments: "The greater the expanse, the greater the ease—how could it be merely a matter of sustenance and settled nature?"

Zigong said: "May I ask about the *jiren*?"
Sima comments: "畸 means unpaired."
Guo comments: "He asks: Where are those who dwell beyond the bounds of the world and do not pair with convention?"
Confucius replied: "The *jiren* is one unpaired with others yet equal to Heaven."
Sima comments: "侔 means equal."
Cheng comments: "Following their original nature, they align with the principle of nature."
"Thus it is said: *What is a petty person in Heaven is a noble person among humans; what is a noble person among humans is a petty person in Heaven.*"
Xuan comments: "Bound by ritual and propriety, ignorant of the true nature of life and destiny, yet humans call them refined."
*Note: All versions agree on this text. It seems repetitive and without meaning; it should likely read: "What is a noble person in Heaven is a petty person among humans."*
Cheng comments: "Zifan and Qin Zhang, unpaired with convention, are called *jiren*—truly noble persons in Heaven."
*Note: To be unpaired with convention means to reject ritual propriety, so humans all disdain them. Thus it is said: "What is a noble person in Heaven is a petty person among humans." The meaning is clear.*
Su Yu comments: "If we judge the *jiren* by the standards of petty humans, then even Qin Zhang and Mengsun would be deemed unworthy. Could Zhuangzi truly be one who does not understand ritual?"

Yan Hui asked Confucius: "Mengsun Cai—what sort of talent is he?"

Yan Hui said: "When his mother died, he wept without tears, his heart felt no grief, and he showed no sorrow in mourning. Lacking these three, he was yet praised throughout Lu for his mastery of mourning rites. Could one truly gain such a reputation without substance? I find this most strange."

*Note: Guo Xiang, Lu Deming, and Cheng Xuanying’s versions end the sentence at "mourning."*
Li Zhen comments: "The meaning remains incomplete. The phrase 'praised throughout Lu' should belong to the preceding clause, akin to the line in 'The Emperor of All Things'—'his merit covered all under Heaven.' The *Shi Yan* states: '弇 means to cover.' The *Shi Ming* says: '盖 means to surpass.' Both imply rising above. Thus, Mengsun Cai’s reputation for mastery of mourning rites surpassed all in Lu."

Confucius said: "Mengsun Cai has exhausted the Way—he has surpassed mere knowledge. As Cheng notes: '进 means to exceed.' Xuan comments: 'He has fully realized the Dao, surpassing those who merely know mourning rites.' He sought simplicity but could not fully attain it. Xuan comments: 'Simplicity means to reduce affairs. Bound by世俗 conventions, he could not entirely dispense with weeping and mourning rites.' Yet he has already simplified much. Xuan comments: 'Still, he wept without tears, felt no grief, and showed no sorrow—this is already a form of simplicity.' Su Yu remarks: 'These two lines are general statements, not specific to Mengsun Cai.' Yao comments: 'Ordinary people are shackled by emotions of life and death, believing sorrow to be natural. Though they cannot fully simplify, they remain unaware that in the truth of nature and destiny, they have already simplified much.' This seems superior to Xuan’s interpretation.*

Mengsun Cai did not know why he lived, nor why he died. Xuan comments: 'He entrusted life and death to nature—this is how he surpassed mere knowledge.' He did not seek what came before, nor what came after. Cheng comments: 'Before refers to life; after, to death. United in life and death, he neither departed nor clung.' Transformed into another thing, he awaited the transformations he could not know! Xuan comments: 'He followed the process of transformation, awaiting whatever unknown changes might come.' Consider: death is transformation into a ghostly thing. But who knows if he might not become a rat’s liver or an insect’s limb—transformations beyond knowing! And if he is about to transform, how could one know he has not already done so? If he is about to remain unchanged, how could one know he has not already transformed? Xuan comments: 'These four lines describe the unknowable transformations—none of which are within our control.' Are you and I merely dreamers who have yet to awaken? Xuan comments: 'We have not yet attained Mengsun Cai’s surpassing knowledge.'

Moreover, he experienced a startling change in form but suffered no harm to his heart. Though Mengsun Cai’s form underwent shocking alteration, his heart remained unburdened. His dwelling renewed daily, yet he did not truly die. Cheng comments: '旦 means daily renewal. 宅 is the abode of the spirit. The body’s changes are but the daily renewal of this dwelling.' Yao comments: '情 means reality. He knew there was no true death.' Mengsun Cai alone was awake. When others wept, he wept too—this was simply how things were. *乃 means "thus." When others wept, he wept without contrivance.* Su Yu comments: 'The phrase "Mengsun Cai alone was awake" stands alone. We are all dreamers, but Mengsun alone is awake. When others wept, he wept too—this was his spontaneous response.'

Furthermore, people see this temporary body of mine and call it "me." But how can they know what I truly mean by "me"? Is this body truly me, or not? You dream of becoming a bird and soaring to the heavens, *厉 and 戾 are homophones, meaning "to reach."* or of becoming a fish and sinking into the depths. But who can say whether the speaker now is awake or dreaming? Is the bird or fish the dream, or is the one speaking of them the dream? One stumbles upon joy before laughter arises; laughter erupts before one can arrange it. Xuan comments: 'People assume laughter arises from joy, yet when joy suddenly strikes, the heart grasps it before laughter comes. And when laughter bursts forth, it is the spontaneous movement of nature—one cannot contrive or arrange it. Thus, joy and laughter are not under our control.' If you calmly accept the arrangements of transformation, you enter the vast oneness of Heaven. Xuan comments: 'From this, we see that nothing is within our control—there is always something arranging it in the unseen. One should simply accept these arrangements, letting go of sorrow over death and transformation, and thus enter the empty, unified vastness of Heaven.'"

Yi’erzi went to see Xu You. Xu You asked: "How did Yao assist you?" Cheng comments: "Yi’er was a worthy man of old." Guo comments: "资 means to provide or aid." Yi’erzi replied: "Yao told me: 'You must personally practice benevolence and righteousness, and clearly distinguish right from wrong.'" Cheng comments: "You must practice them yourself and also make them clear to others." Xu You said: "Then why have you come here?" *而 means "you." 轵 is equivalent to 只.*

"Now, Yao has already branded you with benevolence and righteousness and mutilated you with right and wrong—*as if subjecting you to punishment.* How then will you wander the paths of carefree wandering, unbridled freedom, and ceaseless transformation?"

Yi’erzi said: "Though this be so, I still wish to wander within its hedges." *Though I cannot follow the Way itself, I hope to dwell near its fences.*

Xu You said: "It cannot be. The blind have no part in the beauty of brows and eyes or the radiance of countenance; the sightless cannot share in the splendor of blue and yellow, embroidered patterns and brocade."

Yi’erzi said: "Wu Zhuang lost her beauty—*Wu Zhuang was a beauty of old who, upon hearing the Dao, no longer adorned herself and forgot her own loveliness.* Ju Liang lost his strength—*Ju Liang was a man of great strength who, upon hearing the Dao and embracing stillness, lost his power.* The Yellow Emperor lost his wisdom—*though the Yellow Emperor possessed sagely knowledge, upon hearing the Dao, he was able to cast it aside.* All this occurred within the forge and hammer." *The forge is the hearth; the hammer, the mallet. These three, having grasped the Dao and aligned with truth, were like vessels shaped by the furnace and anvil to serve their purpose.*

"How do we know the Creator will not cease my branding and mend my mutilation, allowing me to ride in completion and follow you, Master?"

*To ride is to be carried. Branding and mutilation leave the body incomplete; if they cease and are mended, wholeness is restored. Heaven has now brought me to you, Master—how do we know it will not allow me to be carried in wholeness and follow you?"*

Xu You said: "Alas! It cannot be known."

"Let me speak to you of its general outline.

O my Master! O my Master!
Crushing the myriad things yet not acting for righteousness,
bestowing blessings upon ten thousand generations yet not acting for benevolence—
Sima [Biao] says: '● means to shatter.'
Lu Wenchang says: 'The *Shuowen* writes it as ●, also as . The clerical script simplifies it to .'
Cheng [Xuanying] says: 'Autumn frost descends and shatters the myriad things, yet it does so without intent to sever, thus not acting for righteousness. The harmonious qi of spring nurtures and gives life to the myriad things, yet it does so without affectionate intent, thus not acting for benevolence.'

Ageless since high antiquity yet not deemed old—
Cheng [Xuanying] says: 'Before the myriad forms existed, this Dao already was, ever renewing without end.'

Enveloping heaven and earth, carving and sculpting the forms of all things yet not deemed artful—
Cheng [Xuanying] says: 'Heaven covers and earth bears all through the Dao as their source; the carving of all forms relies on the transformations of creation. Since all equally receive this natural endowment, the name of artifice vanishes.'

This is the realm in which he roams."
Xuan [Ying] says: "This responds to the earlier 'roaming.'"

Yan Hui said: "I have gained much."

Confucius said, "What do you mean?"
Yan Hui replied, "I have forgotten benevolence and righteousness."
Confucius said, "That is acceptable, yet still not sufficient."

Another day, they met again, and Yan Hui said, "I have gained much."
Confucius asked, "What do you mean?"
Yan Hui replied, "I have forgotten ritual and music."
Confucius said, "That is acceptable, yet still not sufficient."

Another day, they met again, and Yan Hui said, "I have gained much."
Confucius asked, "What do you mean?"
Yan Hui replied, "I have attained 'sitting in forgetfulness.'"
Sima [Biao] says: "Sitting and forgetting oneself entirely."

Confucius started and asked, "What is 'sitting in forgetfulness'?"
Yan Hui said, "I discard my limbs and body, dismiss my wisdom and clarity."
Cheng [Xuanying] says: "Discard means to abolish; dismiss means to set aside."
"I detach from form and cast off knowledge,"
Xuan [Ying] says: "Summarizing the two lines above."
"and merge with the Great Thoroughfare."
Cheng [Xuanying] says: "Becoming obscurely one with the great Dao."

Confucius said, "To be one is to have no preferences,"
Xuan [Ying] says: "Without partiality."
"to transform is to have no constancy."
Xuan [Ying] says: "Without attachment to principles."
"How truly worthy you are! I would ask to follow after you."

Ziyu and Zisang were friends. When continuous rain fell for ten days,—
Rain lasting three days or more is called *lin*—
Ziyu said, "Zisang is likely in distress!" He wrapped rice and went to feed him.

Upon reaching Zisang’s door, he heard something between singing and weeping, as Zisang plucked his zither and said:
"Father! Mother! Heaven! Humanity!"
His voice could not bear the strain, yet he hurriedly recited his verse.

Cui [Wei] says: "Unable to bear his voice—he was exhausted."
Cheng [Xuanying] says: "Trend: hasty and urgent."

Ziyu entered and said, "Why do you sing your verse in such a manner?"
Cheng [Xuanying] says: "His singing seemed resentful, so Ziyu asked in surprise what had caused it."

Zisang replied, "I pondered what brought me to this extremity, yet found no answer. Would my parents wish me to be poor? Heaven covers all without partiality; Earth bears all without partiality—would Heaven and Earth single me out for poverty? I sought the cause, yet could not find it. Nevertheless, to reach this extremity is fate indeed!" To know what fate has wrought is simply to follow it.

**Inner Chapters, "Fit for Emperors and Kings," Chapter Seven**
Guo [Xiang] says: "To be without intent and yield to self-transformation—this is how one fits to be emperor or king."

Nie Que questioned Wang Ni, asking four times and receiving four times no answer. (See "The Equality of Things and Opinions.")

Nie Que leapt with great joy and went to tell Master Pu Yi.
*Shiwen* notes: "The *Shizi* says, 'At eight years old, Pu Yi was offered the throne by Shun.' Cui [Wei] says, 'That is, Clothed-in-Rags, Wang Ni’s teacher.' The *Huainanzi* says, 'Nie Que sought the Dao from Clothed-in-Rags.'"

Master Pu Yi said, "So now you have understood it? You."

The Youyu clan did not match the Tai clan. *Cheng* notes: "The Tai clan refers to Taihao Fuxi." The Youyu clan still harbored virtue to bind people, and though it gained people’s allegiance, it had not yet emerged from the realm of the non-human. *Cui* notes: "This means cherishing virtue to win people’s hearts." *Xuan* comments: "The non-human refers to things. To bind people with intent is still to strike at things—this is not yet to transcend beyond them."

The Tai clan, in its repose, was serene; in its awakening, unknowing. *Sima* notes: "Serene describes its tranquil slumber; unknowing, its lack of awareness." At times it took itself for a horse, at times for an ox. *Cheng* notes: "Whether horse or ox, it answered to whatever it was called." Its knowledge was true to its nature, *Cheng* notes: "It followed its genuine knowing, its feelings free of pretense." Its power was utterly real. *Guo* says: "It let things be as they were, so there was no falsity." Yet it had never entered the realm of the non-human. *Xuan* comments: "It merged with the natural, utterly unburdened by things, never sinking into their midst."

Jian Wu met the madman Jie Yu. Jie Yu asked: "What did Rizhong Shi tell you?" *Li* notes: "Rizhong Shi was the name of a worthy man." The *Cui* edition omits the character "日" and reads: "Zhong Shi was a worthy man." *Yu* comments: "日 here means 'recently,' as in the *Zuozhuan* entries for the seventh year of Duke Wen, the twenty-sixth year of Duke Xiang, the seventh year of Duke Zhao, and the nineteenth year of Duke Zhao."

Jian Wu replied: "He told me that a ruler, by imposing his own standards—laws, models, norms, and measures—*Sima* notes: "出 means 'to enact.'" *Wang Niansun* comments: "经式义度 all refer to regulations; 义 should be read as 仪, as the characters were interchangeable in antiquity."—could make people obey and transform without daring to resist!"

Jie Yu said: "This is deceptive power. *Cheng* notes: "To impose one’s will on things is to strip them of their truth—this is a deceitful power, not the true Way." In governing the world, it is like crossing the sea to dig a river or carrying a mountain on one’s back. The sage’s governance—does it concern externals? To rely on laws is to govern externals. First rectify, then act; first align with nature, then enact transformation. It is simply to ensure that each does what it is truly capable of. *Li* notes: "确 means 'firm.'" *Xuan* comments: "Not forcing people to do what their nature finds difficult."

Moreover, birds fly high to evade the harm of nets and arrows, while field mice burrow deep beneath sacred mounds to escape the peril of fumigation and digging. *Cheng* notes: "矰 refers to nets; 鼷鼠, a tiny mouse; 神丘, an altar to the earth god." *Xuan* comments: "Even creatures know this much." How much more ignorant are these two creatures—how could humans be less knowing than they?"

Tian Gen wandered in Yin Yang, *Cui* notes: "A place name." and arrived at the banks of the Liao River. *Li* notes: "Liao River, a river name." There he encountered the Nameless Man and asked: "May I inquire about governing the world?"

The Nameless Man said: "Begone! You are a crude man—why do your questions never weary?" *Yu* comments: "*Shuowen* defines 豫 as 'weary.' In the *Chuci*’s *Xi Song*, '行婞直而不豫兮,' Wang Yi’s annotation reads: '豫 means weary.' Here, the Nameless Man chides Tian Gen for his endless questions, as if to say, 'Why are you not afraid of tiring me?'"

"I am about to join the Creator in becoming human—*human* here means *companion*, as explained in the *Great and Venerable Teacher* chapter. Weary of this, I will then ride the boundless bird of the vast and distant—*Cheng* notes: "莽眇 means 'profound and remote.'" *Note*: This refers to the ethereal qi, like a bird.—to soar beyond the six directions *Cheng* notes: "六极 refers to the six directions." and wander in the land of Nothing Whatever, as described in the *Free and Easy Wandering* chapter. There I will dwell in the wild expanse. *Cui* notes: "圹埌 means 'vast and unbounded.'" Why do you trouble my heart with your schemes for governing the world?" *帠*: Xu pronounces it as *yi*, but the character is unclear. The *Cui* edition reads "为," which should be followed.

Tian Gen asked again. The Nameless Man replied: "Let your mind wander in simplicity, harmonize your qi with stillness, follow the natural course of things, and harbor no private designs—*Xuan* comments: "Do not rely on your own cleverness."—and the world will be governed."

Sun Ziju visited Lao Dan and said—*Cheng* notes: "His surname was Yang, his courtesy name Ziju." *Note*: This refers to Yang Zhu, as explained in the *Parables* chapter—"Suppose there were a man here who is swift and resolute, keen and decisive, whose understanding of things is penetrating and clear, and who studies the Dao without weariness. Could such a one be compared to an enlightened king?"

Lao Dan replied: "In the eyes of a sage, he is like a petty officer bound to his duties or a craftsman shackled to his skill—one who exhausts his body and startles his mind. *Guo Qingfan* notes: '胥徒 refers to commoners conscripted for corvée labor. 易 means 'to manage.' 胥易 thus means petty officers performing their duties. 技系 refers to the *Rites of Zhou* passage: 'Those who serve their superiors with a skill shall not take on a second occupation nor transfer to another office'—they are bound by their craft.' Moreover, the patterns of tigers and leopards attract hunters, and the agility of gibbons and macaques brings them capture. *Note*: 便 means 'nimble.' A dog skilled in seizing prey is leashed. *Sima* notes: '藉 means 'to bind.' *Note*: Monkeys and dogs are bound because of their abilities. Both phrases also appear in the *Heaven and Earth* chapter. Could such a one be compared to an enlightened king?"

Sun Chujū bowed deeply and asked, "May I inquire about the governance of an enlightened king?"

Lao Dan replied, "The governance of an enlightened king: his achievements cover all under heaven yet seem not his own. *Cheng Xuanying* notes: 'The sage’s accomplishments are complete, yet he does not claim them as his own.' He transforms and bestows upon the ten thousand things, yet the people do not rely on him. *Xuan Ying* notes: 'Bestows, meaning to grant.' *Cheng Xuanying* notes: 'The common folk believe they do not depend on the ruler’s ability.' He possesses something yet cannot be named. *Xuan Ying* notes: 'He seems to have it, yet it cannot be named.' He allows things to delight in themselves. *Cheng Xuanying* notes: 'Each thing attains its own fulfillment.' He stands in the unfathomable and roams in the realm of nothingness. *Xuan Ying* notes: 'What abides is spirit; he acts without contrivance.'"

In Zheng there was a divine diviner named Ji Xian. The *Liezi* chapter "The Yellow Emperor" states: "A divine diviner came from Qi and settled in Zheng; he was called Ji Xian." He knew the life and death, survival and perishing, fortune and misfortune, longevity and early death of men, predicting them by year, month, or day with godlike accuracy. Whether a year, a month, or a day, none of his predictions failed. When the people of Zheng saw him, they all fled in haste. *Xuan Ying* notes: "Fearing he would speak of their misfortune." When Liezi saw him, he was spellbound. *Xiang Xiu* notes: "Bewitched by his way." Returning, he told Master Hu, *Liezi* writes "Master Huqiu." *Sima Biao* notes: "His name was Lin, a man of Zheng and Liezi’s teacher." saying, "At first I thought my master’s Dao was supreme, but now there is something even more supreme."

Master Hu said, "I have taught you only its outward forms, not yet its true substance—how could you have truly attained the Dao? *Cheng Xuanying* notes: '与 means 'to transmit.' 既 means 'to exhaust.' Compared to what I have transmitted to you, you have only grasped the words, not yet penetrated their profound truth. How can you cling to mere words and claim to have attained the Dao?'" *Note*: The *Liezi* reads "without its outward forms." *Zhang Zhan*’s commentary cites *Xiang Xiu*: "Truth is revealed through form; the Dao is made manifest through affairs. To have the Dao without affairs is like having a female without a male. Now, though you and I differ in depth, without form to bring it forth, the true substance of my Dao remains unexhausted. This means that when a sage initiates, there must be a response before there is harmony."

"A flock of hens without a rooster—how can they lay eggs? *Guo Xiang* notes: 'This metaphor means Liezi has not yet embraced the Dao.' And you, seeking to contend with the world and force your way, *Note*: 而 refers to you. 信 is read as 'to extend.' Your Dao is still shallow, yet you contend with the world, striving to impose yourself. The *Liezi* writes 亢 as 抗. Thus you allow others to perceive and assess you. Bring him here and let me show myself to him."

The next day, Liezi brought Ji Xian to see Master Hu. Upon leaving, Ji Xian said to Liezi, "Alas! Your master is dead—he will not live. It will not be counted in days! I saw something strange: I saw damp ashes." *Xuan Ying* notes: "He spoke of lifeless embers."

Liezi entered, his tears soaking his lapels, and told Master Hu. Master Hu said, "Just now I showed him the pattern of earth: *Liezi*’s commentary cites *Xiang Xiu*: 'Like a clod of soil.' Sprouting yet unmoved, unshaken. *Yu Yue* notes: 'The *Liezi* reads: '罪乎不震不止,' which should be followed. 罪 is read as ●, written in the *Shuowen* as ●, meaning 'the appearance of a mountain.' 震 is an alternate form of 震. 不震不止 means 'unmoving and unceasing,' hence described as ●乎, likening it to a mountain. Now 罪 is mistakenly written as 萌, and 止 as 正, losing the original meaning.' According to the *Exegesis*, *Cui Zhuan*’s version reads '不震不止,' matching the *Liezi*, which can be used to correct the text." *Note*: *Liezi*’s commentary cites *Xiang Xiu*: 'Unmoving, yet not ceasing of itself, like a withered tree sharing its lack of blossoms, or dead ashes equal in their stillness—this is the time when the perfected one is without response.' This was nearly showing him my blocked virtue-mechanism. *Cheng Xuanying* notes: '杜 means 'to block.'' The *Liezi* writes 机 as 几. Its commentary cites *Xiang Xiu*: 'The mechanism of virtue does not emerge, hence called 'blocked.'' Bring him here again." *Note*: 尝 also means 'to try.'"

The next day, he brought him again to see Master Huzi. Upon leaving, he said to Liezi: "How fortunate! Your master has met me. He is recovering, fully alive now." *Liezi* writes 全 as 灰. "I have seen his blocked adaptability." *Xuan* notes: "Within the blockage, I sensed his capacity for change."

Liezi entered and reported this to Master Huzi. Huzi said: "Earlier, I showed him Heaven and Earth." *Liezi*’s commentary cites *Xiang Xiu*: "Within Heaven and Earth, the power of covering and bearing is manifest. Compared to the patterns of earth, is this not external?" *Note*: Guo’s commentary writes "之地" for "地之" and "卵" for "外," which are likely errors. Some claim Guo plagiarized *Xiang*’s commentary—perhaps not entirely, but cases like this may approach it. "Name and reality did not enter him." *Liezi*’s commentary cites *Xiang Xiu*: "When one follows the natural and embraces the covering and bearing, then name and reality both become discarded things." *Note*: Guo’s commentary adds after "则": "the mysterious response of the celestial mechanism, and the adornments of name and profit all become discarded things." "Yet the mechanism arose from his heels." *Xuan* notes: "A surge of vital mechanism, issuing from the heels." "This was nearly showing him my mechanism of goodness." *Xuan* notes: "Goodness here means the impulse of life."

"Bring him here again."

The next day, he brought him again to see Master Huzi.

Listz emerged and said to Liezi: "Your master is not in equipoise. *Shiwen* notes: *ce zai* (侧皆) *fan* (反), with an alternate reading as *zhai* (斋). Below likewise." I could find no basis to assess him. Try to attain equipoise, and I shall assess him again."

Liezi entered and reported this to Master Huzi. Huzi said: "Just now I showed him the Great Thoroughfare in its unopposable vastness. *Liezi* writes *zhen* (眹) for *sheng* (胜), which should be followed. The *Xiang* commentary states: 'Abiding at the utmost of the Great Thoroughfare, with a mind vast and still, one merges with the ten thousand directions—no trace of it can be seen.'" *Note*: Guo’s commentary renders "no trace of it can be seen" as "thus neither victory nor defeat can find place therein."

This was nearly showing him my balanced qi-mechanism. *Xuan* notes: "Heng (衡) means level." The *Liezi* commentary cites *Xiang*: "Level in all directions, merged into oneness." *Note*: Guo’s commentary is identical.

The depth where the *ni*-fish coils is a deep pool; the depth where still water abides is a deep pool; the depth where flowing water abides is a deep pool. There are nine names for deep pools—here three are given. *Liezi* writes "the depth where the *ni*-fish coils" as "the swirling eddy of the *ni*-fish." Zhang’s commentary holds that it should be *pan* (蟠), stating: "The *ni* is a great fish. *Huan* (桓) means to coil. *Pan* (蟠) means to swirl. It speaks of the great fish coiling, its waters swirling to form a deep spring."

The nine names for deep pools are: *ni*-coiling, still water, flowing water, overflowing water (*Er ya*: "water gushing forth"), cascading water (spring water flowing down from above), lateral water (spring water issuing from the side), dammed water (river water bursting forth and then returning), streaming water (water flowing onward), and fertile water (water issuing from different sources to become fertile). These are the nine deep pools, all from *Liezi*’s text.

*Cheng* comments: "The water’s body is without intent; its movement and stillness follow things. Whether whales and *ni*-fish coil, or it congeals and abides still, or waves surge and churn—though there are many kinds, all are one in their dark silence and absence of intent."

"Bring him here again."

The next day, he again brought him to see Master Huzi. Before he could stand firmly, the visitor fled in alarm. Master Huzi said, "Pursue him!" Liezi chased after him but could not catch up. Returning, he reported to Master Huzi, saying, "He has vanished, he is lost—I could not reach him."

Master Huzi said, "Earlier, I showed him that which had not yet emerged from my origin. Deeply rooted and profoundly obscure, he could not perceive the master of my origin. I met him with emptiness and followed along." *Cheng* comments: "Commissioned compliance, an appearance of following along." *Guo* comments: "Without intent, one follows the transformations of things." *Note*: In *Liezi*, "commissioned compliance" is written as "yielding movement," but the meaning is the same. "He knew not who or what it was." *Xiang* comments: "Floating freely, without attachment." *Note*: *Guo*’s annotation is the same. "Thus, I made him like trailing grass." *Shiwen* notes: "The pronunciation of *di* is like *tui*. *Dimi*, an appearance of boundlessness."
