Lu Wenchang comments: "The *Zhengzitong* writes *di* as ●, and later dictionaries followed suit, though this character does not appear in ancient texts. The *Leipian* lists an alternate pronunciation for *di* as *tuihui*, glossing it as 'an appearance of boundlessness.' This is the original source. In *Liezi*, it is written as 'marsh grass.'"

"Thus, he made him like flowing waves." Cui's edition reads "wave-following," with the annotation: "Always following along." Wang Niansun comments: "Cui's edition is correct. *She*, *he*, *mi*, and *sui* form a rhyme. *She* in ancient pronunciation is *tuohe* (徒禾反). *Mi* in ancient pronunciation is *mo* (摩). *Sui* in ancient pronunciation is also *tuohe* (徒何反)." "Therefore, he fled." Cheng comments: "Following the momentum of the moment without any attachment, undergoing myriad transformations—none of which the physiognomist could comprehend—hence Jiyuan fled."

*Note*: The *Liezi* commentary cites Xiang's annotation: "The perfected person's movement is like heaven, their stillness like earth, their conduct like flowing water, their depth like a silent abyss. The silent abyss and flowing water, the movement of heaven and the stillness of earth—though differing in form, their spontaneity and non-action are one. Now, when Jiyuan saw him dwelling like a corpse and sitting in oblivion, he called it approaching death; when he saw his spirit moving in celestial accord, he called it possessing life. If one responds to stimuli without intent, ascending and descending with change, taking the world as measure, then one may truly master things and follow the timeless without limit—how could a physiognomist perceive this?"

Thereafter, Liezi, considering himself as yet unlearned, returned home. Cheng comments: "Only then did he realize the profundity of Master Huqiu's Dao and recognize his own lack of learning." For three years, he did not venture out. He cooked for his wife. Xiang comments: "Abandoning shame and disgrace."

Eating pork was to him as eating human flesh. *Li's commentary: "食 pronounced as sì."* Guo comments: "Forgetting distinctions of noble and base." He took no part in affairs, nor drew near to worldly matters. Carving and polishing returned to simplicity. Cheng comments: "All tasks of ornate adornment were wholly set aside, reverting to plainness." Solitary, he stood alone in his form—block-like, without peer. In tumult he was sealed. *Li's commentary: "纷而, Cui comments: 'a state of chaos.' 哉, Cui’s text reads 戎, explaining: '封戎 means scattered disorder.'" Li Zhen comments: "Cui’s version is correct. *Liezi* reads '●然而封戎.' The six lines—人, 亲; 朴, 立; 戎, 终—each form their own rhyme."* Thus he ended his days. Xuan comments: "The Dao admits no further addition. Citing the cases of Jiyuan and Master Hu, it clarifies that emperors and kings should empty themselves of action, standing in the unfathomable, so that the world cannot discern their signs and thus awaken cunning. Its purport is subtle and boundless." The above five matters serve as proof.

The character "也" in the *Jishi* edition is written as "矣."

To be no master of fame, Cheng comments: "Master here means controller. To be no controller of fame and reputation."
To be no repository of schemes, no repository of plans and deliberations.
To be no bearer of tasks, Guo comments: "To entrust things so each bears its own charge."
To be no lord of knowledge. *Shiwen* notes: "知 here pronounced as *zhi* (wisdom)." Cheng comments: "Not to deploy wisdom in lording over things."

To embody the boundless, to fathom the true source and merge with the infinite.
To wander without trace, Cui comments: "朕 means sign." Cheng comments: "朕 means mark. Concealing one’s tracks and veiling one’s light, thus leaving no trace."

To fulfill what one receives from Heaven, yet without any appearance of attainment, to preserve wholly what one receives from Heaven without any sense of self-possession.
To be empty, and nothing more. Guo comments: "Without emptiness, one cannot bear the fullness of the multitude."

The mind of the perfected person functions like a mirror, Guo comments: "Reflecting things without emotion."
Neither sending off nor welcoming, responding yet not storing away, Cheng comments: "将 means to send off. Things stir and one responds, yet the response does not arise from the mind. Since there is neither sending off nor welcoming, how could there be any concealment of emotion?"
Thus one can overcome things without harm. Cheng comments: "The mind does not labor, and so suffers no injury."

This is the main text of the passage.

The Lord of the Southern Sea was Shu, the Lord of the Northern Sea was Hu, and the Lord of the Central Realm was Hundun. Jianwen comments: "Shu and Hu take their names from divine swiftness; Hundun, from harmonious undifferentiation. Divine swiftness is a metaphor for action, while harmonious undifferentiation is a metaphor for non-action." Cui comments: "Hundun has no apertures." Shu and Hu would often meet in Hundun’s land, and Hundun treated them very well. Shu and Hu conspired to repay Hundun’s virtue, saying: "All people have seven apertures for seeing, hearing, eating, and breathing, yet this one alone has none. Let us try to bore them for him." Each day they bored one aperture, and on the seventh day, Hundun died. Guo comments: "To act is to ruin it." This is the allegorical meaning.

Su Yu notes: "The four chapters below *Knotted Toes* mostly expound the meaning of the *Laozi*. Though Zhuangzi delighted in the teachings of Laozi, his self-proclaimed aspirations were far loftier, as can be seen in the *Under Heaven* chapter. Beyond explicating Laozi, these four chapters contain no profound insights; they were likely composed by Zhuangzi’s followers, who used Laozi as their basis. Moreover, their style is straightforward and unelaborated, lacking the vast and unfathomable wonder of the Inner Chapters. Wang Fuzhi and Yao Nai both suspected that the Outer Chapters were not written by Zhuangzi, and this is most perceptive. For instance, this chapter begins by speaking of 'excess and perversion in the practice of benevolence and righteousness,' yet ends by placing 'excess and perversion' and 'benevolence and righteousness' on equal footing—its contradictions are glaring. It also states, 'I am ashamed before the Dao and its virtue,' but how could Zhuangzi ever have uttered such a humble phrase?"

Knotted toes and supernumerary fingers arise from nature, yet they exceed in virtue. Li comments: "Knotted means conjoined." Cheng comments: "The big toe is joined to the second toe; a supernumerary finger means the hand has six fingers." Cui comments: "Exceed means to go beyond." Note: Since they are present from birth, they are said to arise from nature. Virtue here means attainment. What one attains surpasses that of others. Excrescences and hanging wens arise from form, yet they exceed in nature. Excrescences and hanging wens appear in the *Great and Venerable Teacher* chapter. Since they attach after form is complete, they are said to arise from form, yet they go beyond natural nature. Those who employ benevolence and righteousness in manifold ways store them in the five viscera, yet this is not the proper alignment of the Dao and its virtue. Cheng comments: "Manifold ways refer to methods." Note: Those who apply many methods to practice benevolence and righteousness take the five constants (human nature) as shared by all people and arrange them in the five viscera, aligning them with the five phases, yet this is not the original state of the Dao and its virtue.

Thus, conjoined toes are merely useless flesh; supernumerary fingers are merely useless digits. *Shu* means to establish. Those who employ manifold methods to conjoin and branch the passions of the five viscera—passions here mean reality—exceed and pervert the practice of benevolence and righteousness. Exceed means to go beyond; perversion means deviation from the norm, hence it is called excess and perversion. And those who employ manifold methods in the use of hearing and sight.

Thus, those conjoined in brightness disrupt the five colors, indulge in ornate patterns, and dazzle with blue, yellow, embroidered, and brocaded splendor—is this not so? And this begins with Li Zhu and his like. This means it originates with people like Li Zhu.

"Cheng [Yun] states: 'An axe-shaped pattern is called *fu*; two *ji* characters back-to-back are called *fu*. The five colors are blue, yellow, red, white, and black. Blue and red form *wen*; red and white form *zhang*. *Huanghuang* describes a dazzling appearance.' Sima [Biao] states: 'Li Zhu was a man of the Yellow Emperor’s time who could see the tip of an autumn hair at a hundred paces. Another account says he could see the point of a needle a thousand *li* away. *Mengzi* writes his name as Li Lou.' Those who exceed in hearing disrupt the five notes, indulge in the six pitch-pipes, and dazzle with the sounds of metal, stone, silk, and bamboo—are not the tones of *Huangzhong* and *Dalü* thus? And this begins with Shi Kuang.

*Shiwen* notes: 'Shi Kuang was a grandee of Jin, skilled in music and able to summon ghosts and spirits. *Shiji* records: "A native of Nanhe in Jizhou, born without eyes."' Guo [Xiang] states: 'Those born with ears and eyes are commonly troubled by their longing for the rare and their admiration for the distant. Thus, though Li Zhu and Shi Kuang were keen of sight and hearing, they ultimately brought chaos to the senses.'

Those who branch into benevolence pluck at virtue and obstruct nature to gather fame, causing the world to clamor like reeds and drums in service of laws they cannot attain—is this not so? And this begins with Zeng [zi] and Shi [Qiu]."

Those who "branch into benevolence" refer to those who elevate benevolence and righteousness as if an extra finger sprouts from the hand. Zeng and Shi were by nature inclined toward benevolence and righteousness, yet those whose nature is not so endowed vie to emulate them, causing the world to clamor like reeds and drums in service of laws they cannot attain. Zeng and Shi are Zeng Shen and Shi Qiu.

Wang Niansun notes: "塞 and 擢 are not of the same semantic category. 塞 should be 搴, a graphic error due to their similarity. Both 擢 and 搴 mean 'to pluck' or 'to seize.' The *Guangya* states: '搴 means to take or to pluck.' The *Fangyan* writes it as 攓, glossing it as 'to take,' and specifies that in southern Chu it is called 攓. The *Shuowen* writes it as ●, glossing it as 'to pluck.' The *Chuzhen* chapter of the *Huainanzi* states: 'The learning of the vulgar world plucks at virtue and seizes at nature, inwardly vexing the five viscera and outwardly exhausting the ears and eyes. Only then do they begin to summon spirits and shake the finest threads of things, wielding and brandishing benevolence, righteousness, ritual, and music, displaying their conduct and flaunting their wisdom before the world to clamor for fame and reputation.' It further states: 'Now, the myriad things come forth, plucking at our nature and seizing at our dispositions.' These are all evidence of this usage."

Those who are "paired in disputation" pile words like tiles and knot them like ropes, weaving phrases and roaming their minds among the debates of "hard and white" and "same and different," only to exhaust themselves in pursuit of fleeting reputations and useless speech—is this not so? And this begins with Yang and Mo.

Cui [Yuan] states: "They amass useless words like piling tiles or knotting ropes." Another version reads: "tiles" as "pellets." This refers to distorting and altering phrases, letting the mind wander among the debates of "hard and white" and "same and different." Guo Songtao notes: "敝 means exhaustion. 跬誉 is akin to 'footstep reputation.' A half-step is called a 跬. The *Simafa* states: 'Lifting one foot is called a 跬.' A 跬 is three feet. 跬誉 refers to seeking immediate, short-lived reputation. They exhaust themselves in pursuit of speech that brings fleeting reputation but no real use, hence they are called 'paired in disputation.' Yang Zhu and Mo Di were by nature contentious, so they are singled out here."

Thus, all these are paths of excessive pairing and branching, not the ultimate correctness of the world. That which is truly correct does not lose the genuine dispositions of its nature and destiny. Yu [Yue] notes: "The first 正 is likely a graphic error for 至."

Therefore, what is joined is not called "paired," and what is branched is not called "extended." The *Shiwen* notes: "跂 is read as qí." The *Xuan* edition writes it as "歧." Both 跂 and 歧 mean "to branch."

What is long is not called excessive, and what is short is not called deficient. Thus, though the teal’s legs are short, lengthening them would bring sorrow; though the crane’s legs are long, cutting them would bring grief. Cheng [Xuanying] notes: "凫 refers to a small duck."

Thus, what is long by nature should not be cut, and what is short by nature should not be lengthened—there is nothing to remove and no sorrow to dispel. Xuan [Ying] notes: "Following one’s natural endowment, sorrow naturally vanishes—what need is there to remove it?"

Is it not that benevolence and righteousness run counter to human dispositions? Why then are those who embody benevolence so full of sorrow?

Su Yu notes: "‘Benevolent people’ in the *Xuan* edition is ‘benevolence and righteousness,’ which is correct. Guo Xiang’s commentary states: 'Those who fear that benevolence and righteousness run counter to human dispositions and thus worry about them can truly be called full of sorrow.' It seems his edition also read ‘benevolence and righteousness.’ This passage means that benevolence and righteousness bind people, causing them to lose their constant nature and be plagued by many sorrows. The *Zai You* chapter states: 'They vex their five viscera to fabricate benevolence and righteousness,' which expresses this idea. The error here stems from the following ‘benevolent people.’"

Moreover, those whose toes are "paired" weep when separated, and those whose hands are "branched" cry out when bitten. Whether there is excess in number or deficiency in number, their sorrow is the same. The paired are deficient in number, while the branched have excess in number.

Now, the benevolent of the world gaze anxiously at the world’s calamities and worry over its ills. Sima [Biao] notes: "蒿 means eyes in disarray." Yu [Yue] notes: "蒿 is a loan for ●. The *Yupian* states: '● means bright-eyed, or to gaze.' Thus, ● describes the appearance of gazing. The benevolent who worry over the world must gaze far into the distance, hence the term. ● and 蒿 are phonetically close, so they are interchangeable. In the *Book of Songs*, 'white birds gleaming' is written as '鹤鹤' in the *Mencius* and as '●●' in the *Wen Xuan*’s *Jingfu Palace Rhapsody*. The interchangeability of 蒿 with ● is like that of 翯 with 鹤 and ●."

The unbenevolent, however, breach the genuine dispositions of their nature and destiny to greedily pursue wealth and honor. 决 means to breach, like water bursting through a dam. 情 means reality. 饕 means greed.

Is it not that benevolence and righteousness run counter to human dispositions? Since the Three Dynasties and below, why has the world been so clamorous?

Cheng [Xuanying] notes: "嚣嚣 means noisy and clamorous."

Moreover, those who rely on hooks, cords, compasses, and squares to achieve correctness are whittling away their nature. Cheng [Xuanying] notes: "Hooks for curves, cords for straightness, compasses for circles, and squares for rectangles—all harm one’s original nature."

Those who rely on cords, glue, and lacquer to achieve solidity are encroaching upon their virtue. Cheng [Xuanying] notes: "约 means to bind. This injures their virtue."

Bending and contorting with ritual and music, coaxing and nurturing with benevolence and righteousness to console the hearts of the world—this is to lose one’s constant nature. Ritual and music’s circumambulations are bending and contorting. 呴俞 is akin to "nurturing and cherishing," a feigned benevolence and righteousness.

The world has a constant nature. What is constant is that the curved do not rely on hooks, the straight do not rely on cords, the round do not rely on compasses, the square do not rely on squares, what is joined does not rely on glue and lacquer, and what is bound does not rely on ropes. The *Shiwen* notes: "The *Guangya* states: '纆 means rope.'"

Thus, the world is naturally guided into life, yet does not know how it comes to live. Xuan [Ying] notes: "诱然 suggests being led as if by something into life."

All alike attain it, yet know not how they attain it. Thus, past and present share one unchanging principle—it cannot be diminished. Past and present hold no dual principles; they cannot be impaired by human artifice.

Why then do benevolence and righteousness cling like glue, lacquer, ropes, and cords, weaving through the space of Dao and virtue? They only confuse the world!

"Clinging" describes their unbroken continuity. Here, Dao and virtue are exalted while benevolence and righteousness are rejected.

Minor delusion confuses direction, losing one’s bearings. Profound delusion alters one’s nature, forfeiting one’s true essence. How do we know this is so?

Ever since the House of Yu promoted benevolence and righteousness to disturb the world—*Yu* notes: "Promoted, meaning raised up"—the *Shiwen* glosses: "Disturbed, meaning disordered"—the world has rushed to obey their commands, hastening to follow. Has this not exchanged their nature for benevolence and righteousness? *Guo* comments: "Though the House of Yu did not intend to alter nature, the world’s nature was already transformed."

Let us examine this: from the Three Dynasties onward, the world has universally traded its nature for external things. The petty man sacrifices himself for profit; the scholar sacrifices himself for fame—exchanging nature for name and gain. The grandee sacrifices himself for his family; the sage sacrifices himself for the world—exchanging nature for family and empire.

These several types—*Su* notes: "Several types, meaning these various kinds of people"—differ in their undertakings and their reputations, yet in harming their nature and sacrificing themselves, they are one.

Zang and Gu, two men herding sheep together, both lost their sheep. *Shiwen* notes: "*Zhang Yi* says: 'Zang refers to the son of a son-in-law and a maidservant.' *Cui’s* edition writes *Gu* as *●*, stating: 'A child is called *●*.'"

When asked what Zang was doing, he was found clutching bamboo slips and reading. When asked what Gu was doing, he was found gambling and playing games. Though their pursuits differed, their loss of sheep was the same. *Shiwen* notes: "*Ce* is also written as *策*. *Li* says: 'Bamboo slips.' *Sai* refers to games like dice." *Note*: *Ce* should be read as in *Zuozhuan*’s "Rao Chao bestowed a whip"—a herding whip.

Boyi died for fame beneath Shouyang Mountain, while Robber Zhi died for profit atop Dongling. *Cheng* comments: "Zhi was the younger brother of Liuxia Hui, leading nine thousand followers as a notorious bandit. Dongling is a mountain name, also said to be Mount Tai, located in Qi Prefecture, fifteen *li* from Dongping, where Zhi met his end."

Though their deaths differed, in wasting life and harming nature, they were the same. Why must Boyi be right and Robber Zhi wrong? The world is wholly given to sacrifice.

Those who sacrifice for benevolence and righteousness are called gentlemen by custom; those who sacrifice for wealth and goods are called petty men. Their sacrifice is one, yet some are gentlemen, some petty men—*the "then" in "then there are" carries the sense of "and yet."*

If we consider the harm to life and nature, then Robber Zhi is no different from Boyi. What then distinguishes gentleman from petty man? *Xuan* comments: "What justifies their differing names?"

Moreover, those who bind their nature to benevolence and righteousness, though as accomplished as Zengzi and Shishi, are not what I call good. Those who bind their nature to the five flavors, though as skilled as Yu’er, are not what I call good. *Shiwen* notes: "*Sima* says: 'Yu’er was an ancient expert in discerning flavors.' *Cui* says: '*Shizi* states: "The chef Yu’er seasoned food with ginger and cinnamon for the ruler’s table." *Huainanzi* says: "Yu’er and Di Ya could distinguish the waters of Zhi and Mian by taste." One account claims Yu’er lived in the time of the Yellow Emperor. Di Ya is Yi Ya, the flavor expert of Duke Huan of Qi. Another account says Yu’er was also from Qi.'"

Those who bind their nature to the five sounds, though as perceptive as Music Master Kuang, are not what I call keen-eared. Those who bind their nature to the five colors, though as sharp-sighted as Li Zhu, are not what I call clear-sighted.

What I call good is not the so-called benevolence and righteousness—*Xuan* comments: "This phrase may be a corrupted gloss on flavors."—but goodness in one’s inherent virtue alone. What I call good is not the so-called benevolence and righteousness, but following the true essence of one’s nature and fate.

What I call keen-eared is not hearing others, but hearing oneself. What I call clear-sighted is not seeing others, but seeing oneself. *Cheng* comments: "When the mind races and the ears and eyes are exhausted, this is foolishness and blindness—how can it be called keen or clear? If one listens to what the ear hears and sees what the eye sees, preserving one’s allotted nature and remaining unshaken by externals, then all things are keen and clear."

Those who do not see for themselves but see others, who do not attain for themselves but attain others—these attain others’ attainments but do not attain their own, follow others’ paths but do not follow their own. *Guo* comments: "These abandon themselves to imitate others. Though they may imitate others perfectly, they have already lost themselves."

Those who follow others’ paths but do not follow their own—whether Robber Zhi or Boyi—are equally perverse and excessive. *Guo* comments: "If we take the loss of nature as perverse excess, though their paths of loss differ, their loss is one."

*Note*: In the *Da Zongshi* chapter, it says: "Hu Bujie, Wu Guang, Boyi, Shuqi, Jizi, Xu Yu, Ji Ta, Shentu Di—these served others’ service, followed others’ paths, and did not follow their own." Zhuangzi prized preserving life above all, so he deeply disapproved of figures like Boyi. As for Wu Guang, Shentu Di, and others, their circumstances are unclear, but in their time, they may have had ways to avoid death.

The weight of Boyi, Shuqi, and Jizi’s circumstances is too great to be lumped together indiscriminately. Their vision differs from that of the sages. I am ashamed before the Dao and virtue—*Xuan* comments: "A humble phrase."—thus above, I dare not adopt the conduct of benevolence and righteousness, and below, I dare not engage in perverse excess. *Xuan* comments: "Zhuangzi treats benevolence, righteousness, and perverse excess as parallel categories—how can there be a distinction of 'above' and 'below'? These terms, 'above' and 'below,' are used here from the perspective of conventional views."

*Note*: Since the Three Dynasties, the Dao and virtue have been held in the highest esteem, while discussions of benevolence and righteousness have lacked precision. Han Feizi conflated righteousness with benevolence, and this passage likewise subsumes benevolence and righteousness under the category of "benevolent men." Outside the works of Confucius and Mencius, few have been able to discern the distinction between benevolence and righteousness. Zhuangzi of Qiyuan (Lacquer Garden) surely had a subtle point here, yet later Confucians no longer sought it deeply. Han Yu’s *Yuan Dao* opens by explicitly listing the four terms "benevolence," "righteousness," "Dao," and "virtue," guiding scholars in a way that enabled them to transcend the worthies of the Tang and align with the ancient sages.

---

**Chapter 9: "Horse’s Hooves" (External Chapters)**

*Su Yu* comments: "Laozi says, *'Through non-action, things transform themselves; through clarity and stillness, they rectify themselves.'* This entire chapter expounds this principle, culminating in the extended metaphor of the horse—a device not found in Zhuangzi’s Inner Chapters."

Horses’ hooves can tread upon frost and snow; their coats can ward off wind and cold. They graze on grass and drink from streams, lifting their tails to leap. *Annotation*: "Cui’s edition reads *wei* (tail) for *zu* (feet). Sima Biao comments: *'Lu* means to leap; in character dictionaries, it is written as ●. *Lu* signifies a horse’s vigor.'" *Guo Qingfan* comments: "Cui’s edition reads *wei* (tail). The *Wen Xuan*’s *Jiang Fu* commentary also reads *wei*, with *lu* written as 陆, glossed as sounding like *liu*. The *Guangyun* states: *'Lu*, *li zhu* cut, means to prance.'" This is the true nature of horses.

Though there be grand pavilions and stately halls, they are of no use to them. Even the most magnificent dwellings are not what horses’ nature finds fitting. *Annotation*: "*Yi* is pronounced like *yi* (仪) by Xu. *Lu* means 'correct' or 'grand.' Cui comments: *'Luqin* refers to the main hall.'" *Yu* comments: "*Yi* and *yi* (仪) are interchangeable. *Yitai* means 'reception terrace.' The *Huainanzi*’s *Lanming* chapter states, *'The reception terrace trembled and collapsed,'* with Gao You’s note: *'The reception terrace is a terrace for ritual deportment.'"*

Yet when Bo Le arrives, he declares, "I am skilled in managing horses." He brands them, trims them, carves them, and sears them with marks. *Annotation*: "Bo Le, surnamed Sun, named Yang, was skilled in driving horses. Sima Biao comments: *'Shao* means to cauterize with hot iron. *Ti* means to shear their hair.'" *Guo Songtao* comments: "*Luo* is the same as *lao* (烙), meaning to brand." He binds them with reins and fetters, confines them to blackened stables, and arranges them on wooden racks. *Annotation*: "The *Guangya* states: *'Ji* means bridle.' *Zhi* is pronounced *ding yi*. Cui comments: *'Zhi* means to hobble the front and back legs.'" The *Wen Xuan*’s *Dirge for Ma Qian Du* commentary cites Sima Biao: *"Zao* means stable." *Zhan* refers to a lattice bed, used on damp ground." Of the horses, twelve or thirteen out of every hundred die.

He starves them and parches them, races them and gallops them, aligns them and orders them. Ahead, they face the peril of bit-ornaments; behind, the threat of whips and goads. By this point, more than half the horses have died. Sima Biao comments: "*Jue* means bit; *shi* refers to adornments on the horse’s bit." *Cheng* comments: "A whip is covered in leather; a goad is not."

The potter says, "I am skilled in working clay. The round fits the compass; the square fits the carpenter’s square." *Annotation*: "*Tao* means 'to shape.' Cui comments: *'Zhi* means earth.'" The carpenter says, "I am skilled in working wood. The curved fits the hook; the straight aligns with the inked line." Yet do clay and wood’s natures desire to conform to compasses, squares, hooks, and lines? Still, generation after generation, people praise them, saying, "Bo Le is skilled in managing horses, and the potter and carpenter are skilled in working clay and wood." This is also the error of those who govern the world—their mistake is equal to that of governing the world.

In my view, those who are truly skilled in governing the world do not act thus. The people possess an enduring nature: they weave to clothe themselves and till to feed themselves—this is called "shared virtue." *Cheng* comments: "Each thing is self-sufficient, hence 'shared virtue.'" Unified and impartial, it is called "Heaven’s release." *Cheng* comments: "*Dang* means partiality. *Ming* means to name. *Tian* means natural." *Xuan* comments: "Undivided and unbiased, following Heaven’s spontaneity." *Su Yu* comments: "To be one with Heaven and dissolve the partiality of good and evil also conveys the meaning."

Thus, in the age of utmost virtue, their movements were steady and unhurried; their gazes were fixed and unwavering. Cui comments: "*Tian tian* means heavy and slow. *Dian dian* means single-minded." At that time, mountains had no paths or tunnels; marshes had no boats or bridges. *Cheng* comments: "*Qi* means path; *sui* means road." Guo comments: "Not seeking what lies beyond, they were content within their own households." The myriad things thrived in flocks, connected to their native lands. *Xuan* comments: "Each settled in their place, forming connections." Birds and beasts gathered in herds; grasses and trees grew to fullness. Guo comments: "Fulfilling their natures and stopping there, without desires to devour or destroy, thus all things were whole."

Thus, birds and beasts could be led by tethers and roam freely; the nests of crows and magpies could be climbed to peer inside. Guo comments: "Harmonious with things, thus creatures were tame." In the age of utmost virtue, people dwelled alongside birds and beasts, gathered together with the myriad things. *Zu*, meaning to gather. How could one distinguish between noble and base? Alike in lacking knowledge, their virtue did not depart; alike in lacking desire, they were called "plain and uncarved." Plain and uncarved, and the people’s nature was preserved. Guo comments: "Knowledge departs from the Dao by seeking goodness; desire departs from nature by seeking adornment."

When it came to the sage-kings, they hobbled and strained for benevolence, stretched and strained for righteousness, and the world first fell into doubt. Li comments: "Hobbling and straining, stretching and straining—both describe the labored effort to enact benevolence and righteousness." They indulged in extravagance for music, picked and parsed for ritual, and the world first became divided. Li comments: "Extravagance means unbridled excess." Guo Songtao notes: "‘Picked and parsed’ should read ‘picked and split.’ Wang’s commentary on the *Chu Ci* states: ‘Split means to divide.’ Picking is to select; splitting is to dissect—referring to the burdensome and fragmented." Thus, if unhewn simplicity were not marred, who would fashion the sacrificial goblet? If flawless white jade were not shattered, who would carve the ritual tablets? Cheng comments: "Unhewn simplicity means the whole wood before carving. The sacrificial goblet is a wine vessel, carved with an ox’s head, used in ancestral temple rites. A tablet with a pointed top and square base is called a *gui*; half a *gui* is called a *zhang*." If the Dao and virtue were not abandoned, why adopt benevolence and righteousness? Laozi says: "When the great Dao is abandoned, there arise benevolence and righteousness." If nature and feeling were not estranged, why employ ritual and music? Cheng comments: "Ritual restrains conduct; music harmonizes the heart. If feelings were not scattered, why harmonize the heart? If nature were not estranged, why restrain conduct?" If the five colors were not disordered, who would devise patterned adornments? If the five tones were not confused, who would attune them to the six pitch-pipes? Guo comments: "All these transform simplicity into ornament, abandon the root to exalt the branch, and thus mar and waste their natural purity." To mar simplicity to make vessels is the fault of the craftsman; to destroy the Dao and virtue for benevolence and righteousness is the error of the sage. Cheng comments: "To ruin the Dao of non-action with the traces of benevolence and righteousness."

As for the horse, dwelling on land, it eats grass and drinks water; when pleased, it rubs necks together; when angry, it turns its back and kicks. Xuan comments: "A horse’s kick is always backward, hence it is said to turn its back." The horse’s knowledge extends no further than this.

Li's interpretation of *zhi* as "wisdom" is mistaken.

When a crossbar is added to the yoke—*heng* being the horizontal beam at the front of the carriage to which the yoke is bound, and *e* the fork that clasps the horse’s neck—when it is adorned with crescent-shaped ornaments—*yue ti* being the moon-shaped plate on the horse’s forehead—and the horse knows how to glare defiantly, twist its neck against the yoke, thrust and butt, cast off the bit, and steal the bridle—*jie ni* meaning to glare; *yin* meaning to bend; *zhi* meaning to butt; *man* meaning to thrust—twisting its neck against the yoke to thrust and butt, spitting out the bit and slipping the bridle—then the horse’s knowledge and postures reach the point of theft. This is the result of expanding its knowledge to the point where its postures become thieving. It is the fault of Bo Le.

In the age of the Lord of Hexu, people dwelt without knowing what to do, walked without knowing where to go, filled their mouths with food and played, drummed their bellies and wandered. This was all the people were capable of. *Hexu* was an ancient sage-king. *Xi* is interchangeable with *xi* (play). *Yi* and *yi* (already) are used interchangeably.

But when the sages came, they bent and shaped ritual and music to correct the forms of the world, and elevated benevolence and righteousness to console the hearts of the world—*kuang* meaning to correct; *xuan qi* meaning to raise and aspire to, so that people would all admire them. And so the people began to strut and strain with cunning, striving for knowledge and practicing deceit, competing for profit without cease. This, too, is the fault of the sages.

In the *Outer Chapters*, "Opening Trunks":

To guard against thieves who pry open trunks, pick pockets, and break into chests—*qu qie* meaning to open from the side—one must bind them tightly with cords, fasten them securely with locks and bolts. This is what the world calls wisdom. *The* Guangya *says: "Jian* and *teng* are both ropes." Li says: "*Jiong* means a latch; *jue* means a bolt." *Zhi* is pronounced as "wisdom."

Yet when the great thief comes, he shoulders the chest, lifts the trunk, carries the sack, and flees, only fearing that the cords, locks, and bolts are not secure enough. Thus, what the world once called wisdom is nothing but the accumulation of goods for the great thief. *Ye* is interchangeable with *ye* (final particle).

Let us consider this: Is there anything the world calls wisdom that does not accumulate for the great thief? Is there anything it calls sageliness that does not guard for the great thief? How do we know this is so?

In ancient times, the state of Qi had villages so close they could see one another, and the sounds of chickens and dogs could be heard across them. The nets and traps spread, the plows and hoes pierced the earth, covering over two thousand *li* in all directions. Within its four borders, wherever ancestral temples and altars of soil and grain were established, wherever cities, hamlets, districts, villages, and neighborhoods were governed, was there ever a place that did not follow the models of the sages? *The* Art of War *of Sima* says: "Six *chi* make a *bu*, a hundred *bu* make a *mu*, a hundred *mu* make a *fu*, three *fu* make a *wu*, four *wu* make a *yi*." It also says: "Five households make a *bi*, five *bi* make a *lü*, five *lü* make a *zu*, five *zu* make a *dang*, five *dang* make a *zhou*, five *zhou* make a *xiang*." Zheng Xuan says: "Twenty-five households make a *lü*, two thousand five hundred households make a *zhou*, twelve thousand five hundred households make a *xiang*."

Yet Tian Chengzi killed the ruler of Qi in a single day and stole his state. What he stole was not merely the state—he also stole the sagely laws of wisdom along with it. Thus, Tian Chengzi bore the name of a thief and usurper, yet lived in the security of Yao and Shun. Small states dared not condemn him, and great states dared not punish him. For twelve generations, his line held Qi. *From the time Chen Heng assassinated Duke Jian to the day Zhuangzi wrote this, the descendants who ruled Qi had already passed through twelve generations.* Yao says: "From Tian Chang to King Jian, ten generations, including Wuzi of Huan and Qi of Li, make twelve generations. The Tian clan first grew powerful under Wuzi, hence the count includes twelve generations."

Is this not stealing the state of Qi and, along with it, the sagely laws of wisdom to guard his thieving self?

Let us consider this: Is there anything the world calls supreme wisdom that does not accumulate for the great thief? Is there anything it calls supreme sageliness that does not guard for the great thief? How do we know this is so?

Once, Long Feng was beheaded, Bi Gan was disemboweled, Chang Hong was torn apart—*Cui [Yuan] notes: "Read as *tuō*; some write it as 施. 胣 means 'to rend.'" The *Huainanzi* says: "Chang Hong was hacked to pieces and died."*—and Wu Zixu was pulped—*the *Shiwen* reads: "Mǐchí, in the *fanqie* system. Cui [Yuan] notes: 'Dissolved in the river.'"*—so that these four worthies, despite their virtue, could not escape execution.

Guo [Xiang] says: "This shows that even violent and chaotic rulers could wield the authority of a sovereign to execute the worthy, and none dared resist—all because of the sagely laws. Without such sagely laws, how could Jie and Zhou have maintained their thrones to unleash their poison, making the world shudder in fear?"

Su Yu says: "Sagely laws reside in punishments and rewards, yet Jie and Zhou used them to execute the worthy."

Thus, the followers of Robber Zhi asked him: "Does theft also have its Dao?" Zhi replied: "Where could there be no Dao? To divine the treasures hidden in a chamber—*Cheng [Xuanying] notes: "To conceive a reckless thought, weigh and measure, and invariably guess correctly."*—is sageliness. To enter first is courage. To exit last is righteousness. To know what is permissible is wisdom. To divide equally is benevolence. Without these five, no one in the world has ever become a great thief."

From this we see: the good cannot stand without the Dao of the sage, nor can Zhi act without the Dao of the sage. Since the world has few good people and many who are not, the sage’s benefit to the world is slight, while his harm is great. Thus it is said:

"When the lips are exhausted, the teeth grow cold." *Yu [Yan] notes: "This 'exhausted' should be read as in 'exhausting its tail.' The *Shuowen* says beneath 'swine': 'It exhausts its tail, hence it is called swine.' 'Lips exhausted' means to curl them upward."* "When Lu’s wine was thin, Handan was besieged." *Shiwen* [notes]: *Xu Shen’s* commentary on the *Huainanzi* states: "When Chu assembled the feudal lords, Lu and Zhao both presented wine to the King of Chu. Lu’s wine was thin, while Zhao’s was rich. The Chu official in charge of wine sought wine from Zhao, but Zhao refused. Enraged, the official exchanged Zhao’s rich wine for Lu’s thin wine and presented it. The King of Chu, deeming Zhao’s wine thin, thus besieged Handan." "When the sage is born, great thieves arise."

Smash the sages, release the bandits, and the world will begin to be ordered. When rivers run dry, valleys empty; when hills are leveled, abysses fill. Once the sage is dead, great thieves will not rise, and the world will be at peace without incident. While the sage lives, great thieves will not cease. Though one may exalt the sage to govern the world, this is but to amplify the profit of Robber Zhi. When pecks and bushels are made to measure, they are stolen along with the grain; when scales and balances are made to weigh, they are stolen along with the goods; when tallies and seals are made to authenticate, they are stolen along with the authority; when benevolence and righteousness are made to rectify, they are stolen along with the virtues. How do we know this is so? Those who steal hooks are executed—*Cheng [Xuanying] notes: "Hooks for waist belts."*—while those who steal states become feudal lords. At the gates of feudal lords, benevolence and righteousness abide—*Wang [Yinzhi] notes: "'Abide' should read 'abide therein.' 'Therein' means 'in this.' The phrase means benevolence and righteousness exist in this. Ancient texts often use this syntax. (Examples omitted.) Here, 'executed' and 'lords' rhyme, as do 'gates' and 'abide,' with all rhymes at the line-ends. The *Records of the Grand Historian*, in its 'Biographies of Wandering Knights,' reads: 'Those who steal hooks are executed; those who steal states become lords. At the gates of lords, benevolence and righteousness abide,' which clearly demonstrates this."*—is this not the theft of benevolence, righteousness, sageliness, and wisdom?

Thus, those who pursue great thieves, who exalt themselves as feudal lords, who steal the benefits of benevolence and righteousness along with pecks, bushels, scales, balances, tallies, and seals—though offered the rewards of high office, they cannot be encouraged to stop; though threatened with the axe and halberd, they cannot be restrained. That such amplified profit for Robber Zhi cannot be forbidden is the fault of the sage. Thus it is said: "Fish cannot be removed from the deep; the state’s sharp instruments cannot be shown to others." Those sages are the state’s sharp instruments, not what should be displayed to the world. *Display means to show.*

Therefore, sever sageliness and discard wisdom, and great thieves will cease; cast away jade and shatter pearls—*Shiwen* [notes]: "Cast away, meaning the same as 'throw.'"*—and petty thieves will not rise. Burn tallies and break seals, and the people will return to simplicity; smash pecks and snap balances, and the people will not contend. Exhaust and destroy the world’s sagely laws, and only then can one discuss matters with the people. *Shiwen* [notes]: "Exhaust means to deplete."* Throw the six pitch-pipes into disorder and burn the zithers and sevens—*Cheng [Xuanying] notes: "Throw means to pluck."* *Shiwen* [notes]: "Burn means to sever by fire."*—stop up the ears of Music-Master Kuang, and the world will begin to retain its hearing; obliterate patterns and scatter the five colors, glue shut the eyes of Li Zhu, and the world will begin to retain its sight; destroy and abandon hooks and cords, discard compasses and squares, break the fingers of the artisan Chui—*Li [Yi] notes: "Break means to snap."*—and the world will begin to possess its own skill. *Cheng [Xuanying] notes: "Each person’s innate capacity contains its own skill. It is like spiders weaving webs or dung beetles rolling balls—what need is there for artisans?"* Thus it is said: "Great skill appears clumsy."

Sever the conduct of Zeng [Can] and Shi [Qiu], silence the mouths of Yang [Zhu] and Mo [Di], cast aside benevolence and righteousness, and the world’s virtue will begin to merge in profound unity. *Cheng [Xuanying] notes: "When things do not lose their true nature, all people attain self-possession, follow their nature, and fulfill principle—thus they blend with the profound Dao."*

When people retain their sight, the world will not be dazzled; when they retain their hearing, the world will not be burdened—*Cheng [Xuanying] notes: "Burdened means afflicted with cares."*—when they retain their wisdom, the world will not be deluded; when they retain their virtue, the world will not be perverse. Those like Zeng [Can], Shi [Qiu], Yang [Zhu], Mo [Di], Music-Master Kuang, the artisan Chui, and Li Zhu all externally establish their virtues, flaunting their attainments to dazzle and confuse the world—*Shiwen* [notes]: "The *Three Cang* says: 'Dazzle means the dissipation of firelight.'"*—they are of no use to the law. *Xuan [Ying] notes: "In terms of proper governance, all should be discarded."*

Do you alone not know of the age of utmost virtue?

Once, there were Rongcheng, Dating, Bohuang, Zhongyang, Lilü, Lixu, Xuanyuan, Hexu, Zunlu, Zhu Rong, Fuxi, and Shennong—*Sima [Biao] notes: "These twelve were all ancient sage-kings."* In their time, people knotted cords to keep records. They savored their food, admired their clothing, delighted in their customs, and rested content in their dwellings. Neighboring states were within sight, the crowing of roosters and barking of dogs carried between them, yet people lived to old age and death without ever visiting one another. In such an age, perfect governance was already achieved.

Now, however, people crane their necks and stand on tiptoe, saying, "In such-and-such a place there is a worthy!" They bundle their provisions and rush toward him—*Cui [Zhuan] notes: "Bundle means to wrap."*—thus abandoning their kin at home and forsaking their lords’ affairs abroad. Abandoning kin is like Wu Qi; forsaking one’s lord is like Yu Qing. Footprints crisscross the borders of the feudal lords, and carriage tracks intertwine a thousand *li* away—*the tracks are the ruts of wheels; intertwine means to intersect.* This is the fault of those above who favor cunning. If those above truly favor cunning but lack the Way, favoring cunning to disturb things while lacking the Way to pacify them, then the world will fall into great chaos.

How do we know this is so? When the knowledge of bows, crossbows, nets, snares, and mechanical traps abounds, birds are thrown into chaos above. *Li [Yi] notes: "A net for hares is called a *bi*; a weighted arrow is called a *yi*; the trigger of a crossbow is called a *ji*."* *Guo Songtao notes: "The *Shuowen* says: '*Shuai* is a net for catching birds.' The *Book of Songs* says: 'With nets and snares they catch them.' Bird traps are also called *bi*. Li’s explanation is incorrect."* When the knowledge of hooks, bait, dragnets, seines, weirs, and fish traps multiplies, fish are thrown into chaos in the waters. *Wang Niansun notes: "Hook should be *diao* (fishing hook), for *diao* is the same as hook. The *Shiwen* says: '*Diao* means hook.' The present text writes *gou* (hook), which was mistakenly altered by later hands."* *For details, see *Dushu Zazhi*.* When the knowledge of fences, snares, nets, and traps proliferates, beasts are thrown into chaos in the marshes. *Li [Yi] notes: "Fences are used to set up nets."* *Guo Songtao notes: "The *Shuowen Jizhuan* says: 'Long branches are called *ge*.' Fences are scraped and shaped. Fences and snares are all used to intercept and entrap birds and beasts. The *Han Shu* in the biography of Chao Cuo says: 'To make a central enclosure with tiger traps,' and the Yan commentary says: 'This means to enclose them.'"* *The *Shiwen* says: "*Ju* is also written as *fu*. The *Erya* says: 'A rabbit net is called *ju*; a *zhuo* is called *fu*. *Fu* is a covered cart.' Guo Pu says: 'It is today’s overturned cart.'"* When the transformations of cunning deceit, gradual poison, crafty glibness, the obstinacy of "hard and white," the sophistry of "dissolving dirt," and the distinctions of "same and different" multiply, customs are bewildered by disputation. *Guo Qingfan notes: "In the *Xunzi*, the *Fei Shier Zi* chapter says: 'Cunning and dangerous,' the *Yi Bing* chapter says: 'This is gradual corruption,' and the *Zheng Lun* chapter says: 'When those above are treacherous and dangerous, those below gradually become deceitful.' The four characters 'cunning deceit gradual poison' share the same meaning, all referring to fraud."* *The *Shiwen* says: "Crafty glibness refers to improper speech; sophistry refers to devious words."* *Note: Crafty is a loan character for cunning.*

Thus, the world repeatedly falls into great chaos—*Li [Yi] notes: "Repeatedly means as if in darkness."*—and the fault lies in the love of cunning. Therefore, the world all knows to seek what it does not know but does not know to seek what it already knows; all knows to condemn what it does not consider good but does not know to condemn what it already considers good. This is why great chaos arises. Thus, above, it confounds the brightness of the sun and moon; below, it melts the essence of mountains and rivers; and in between, it undermines the operations of the four seasons—*Cheng [Xuanying] notes: "Melts means to dissolve; undermines means to ruin."* Wriggling creatures—*the *Shiwen* says: "Wriggling refers to legless creatures"*—and fluttering things—*Li [Yi] notes: "Things that fly lightly"*—none fail to lose their nature.

How severe is the chaos brought to the world by the love of cunning! This has been the case since the Three Dynasties and after. They abandon the steadfast people and delight in the toiling flatterers—*Li [Yi] notes: "Steadfast describes a cautious and sincere appearance; toiling describes a sly and cunning appearance."* They forsake the tranquil and effortless and delight in the ceaseless chatter—*ceaseless chatter has already thrown the world into chaos.* *Guo [Xiang] notes: "Ceaseless chatter means teaching others with one’s own views."*

**Chapter 11: "Letting Be and Indulgence"**

I have heard of letting the world be and indulging it, but never of governing it. *The *Wen Xuan*, in the commentary to Xie Lingyun’s poem "From Duke Song at the Horse-Racing Platform," cites Sima [Biao] as saying: "Letting be means to examine; indulging means to be lenient."* *Su Yu notes: "Letting be should not be glossed as examining, for examining is already governing. Letting be means to preserve. To preserve in the heart without revealing traces of approval or disapproval, allowing people to live in harmony with their uncarved nature, is the essence of the 'Sealed Casket' chapter."*

Letting be means fearing that the world will overindulge its nature; indulging means fearing that the world will alter its virtue—*altering leads to other imitations.* If the world does not overindulge its nature or alter its virtue, is there any need to govern it? *Guo [Xiang] notes: "What further need is there for governance?"*

In the past, when Yao governed the world, he made the world joyful, each delighting in their nature—this was not tranquility. *Cheng [Xuanying] notes: "Tranquility means stillness."* When Jie governed the world, he made the world weary, each suffering in their nature—this was not pleasure. *Cheng [Xuanying] notes: "Pleasure means joy."* Neither tranquility nor pleasure is virtue. That which is not virtue yet can endure for long—there is nothing like this under heaven.

If people are greatly joyful, they harm the yang; if greatly angry, they harm the yin. *Yu [Yue] notes: "Joy pertains to yang; anger pertains to yin. Harming yang and harming yin means injuring the harmony of yin and yang."*

The *Huainanzi’s* "Original Dao Training" states, *"Great anger shatters the yin; great joy collapses the yang,"* which conveys the same meaning. When both yin and yang are harmed, the four seasons fail to arrive, the harmony of cold and heat cannot form—does this not then injure the human form? *Cheng [Xuanying] notes: "When people suffer many illnesses, is this not the injury to the form returning upon itself?"*

When people’s joy and anger lose their proper place, their dwellings lack constancy, their thoughts and reflections find no fulfillment, and the Dao of the center cannot take shape—only then does the world begin to grow restless and perverse, haughty and fierce. *Cui [Zhuan] notes: "Restless and perverse means the mind is unbalanced; haughty and fierce means conduct is unbalanced."* And only thereafter do the ways of Robber Zhi, Zeng [zi], and Shi [Qiu] arise.

Thus, even if the whole world were mobilized to reward the good, it would not suffice; even if the whole world were mobilized to punish the evil, it would not be enough. *Guo [Xiang] notes: "Those who emulate goodness do so for reward, so rewards cannot meet the demand; those who cease evil do so from fear of punishment, so punishments cannot overcome it."* Therefore, the vastness of the world is still insufficient for rewards and punishments.

From the Three Dynasties onward, the world has been in ceaseless clamor, forever occupied with rewards and punishments—how could it have had leisure to settle the true nature of life? *Cheng [Xuanying] notes: "Clamor means uproar."*

Moreover, delighting in clarity is an excess of form; delighting in keen hearing is an excess of sound. *Note: "Delighting in sound" is the same as above.* Delighting in benevolence is disorder in virtue; delighting in righteousness is perversion of principle; delighting in ritual is mutual assistance in artifice; delighting in music is mutual assistance in excess. *Annotation: "Mutual assistance" means to aid.* *Cheng [Xuanying] notes: "Delighting in ritual aids superficial and ostentatious skills; delighting in music further aids the excess of musical tones."* *Wang Fuzhi notes: "To follow alongside and lose oneself is called mutual assistance."*

Delighting in sageliness is mutual assistance in arts; delighting in knowledge is mutual assistance in flaws. *Cheng [Xuanying] notes: "Delighting in the traces of sages aids the arts of the world; loving clever schemes exacerbates the flaws of right and wrong."*

If the world is to settle the true nature of life, these eight may either exist or vanish—it matters not. But if the world cannot settle the true nature of life, then these eight begin to coil and crumple, to snatch and scramble, throwing the world into chaos. *Sima [Biao] notes: "Coil and crumple describes a state of not being stretched out."* *Cui’s edition reads "戕" (slaughter) instead of "獊," noting: "Slaughter and scramble means to plunder and seize."*

Yet the world still venerates and cherishes them—how deeply deluded the world has become! Is it merely a matter of passing them by and leaving them behind?

"Xuan [Yin] notes: 'Is it merely a matter of passing them by when their time has come and leaving them behind?' Rather, the world fasts and purifies itself to speak of them, kneels in reverence to present them, drums and sings to dance in their honor. *Xuan [Yin] notes: 'Thus, generation after generation delights in offering them, unable to cease.'* What then can I do about it?

Therefore, when a noble person is compelled to govern the world, there is nothing better than non-action. Through non-action, the true nature of life finds peace. Thus, one who values their own person above governing the world may be entrusted with the world; one who cherishes their own person above governing the world may be charged with the world. *Xuan [Yin] notes: 'Valuing and cherishing one’s own person in governing the world means holding the inner as weighty and regarding the outer as light—this is why one who practices non-action in the world may become its ruler.'* *Su Yu notes: 'The two instances of "于" after "身" are likely superfluous. These four lines appear in the *Laozi*.'*

Therefore, if a noble person can refrain from loosening their five viscera—*Guo Xiang notes: 'Loosening means dispersing.'* *See the 'Webbed Toes' chapter: 'Those who multiply methods of benevolence and righteousness and apply them are arrayed among the five viscera.'*—and from flaunting their intelligence—*Zhuo means to pull out, that is, to display and elevate it, speaking of boasting in one’s own intelligence*—abiding in stillness like a corpse yet manifesting like a dragon, profound in silence yet resounding like thunder, unmoving yet spirit-like, wordless yet renowned, then when their spirit moves, Heaven follows. When the spirit first stirs, the celestial mechanism naturally responds. Acting with effortless non-action, the myriad things rise and accumulate like steam. *Sima [Biao] notes: 'Rising and accumulating, like things being steamed and cooked.'* *Xiang [Xiu] and Guo [Xiang] note: 'Like dust motes moving of themselves.'* *See how the warmth of spring causes the myriad things to layer and ripen like steamed grain.* What leisure then do I have to govern the world?

Cui Qu asked Lao Dan: 'If one does not govern the world, how can the human heart be settled?' *The character '藏' is a miswriting of '臧,' as the ancient character was only '臧.' To settle the human heart means there is no way for it to be good.* Lao Dan replied: 'Take care not to agitate the human heart. *Cheng [Xuanying] notes: 'To agitate and disturb the human heart.'* The human heart presses downward and advances upward. *Xuan [Yin] notes: 'When pressed, it descends; when advanced, it ascends.'* Between ascent and descent, it is bound like a prisoner and harmed like a slaughter. *Xuan [Yin] notes: 'Between ascent and descent, it is constrained like a prisoner and injured like a killing.'* *Su Yu notes: 'Its ascent is like killing, its descent like imprisonment. Killing leads to arrogance, imprisonment to rebellion.'* It is yielding and soft yet stronger than the hard and firm. *Cheng [Xuanying] notes: 'Yielding and soft means weak and pliant.'* *Guo [Xiang] notes: 'If it can be yielding and soft, then the hard and firm will be softened.'* Sharp and keen, carved and polished, its heat scorches like fire, its cold congeals like ice. In the swiftness of a single bow and rise, it reaches beyond the four seas. *Reaching means governing; this illustrates its swiftness.* Its abode is deep and still, *Xuan [Yin] notes: 'Speaking of its profound seclusion.'* its movement soars and celestial. *Xuan [Yin] notes: 'Speaking of its soaring flight.'* Reckless and arrogant, it cannot be restrained—is this not the human heart?

In ancient times, the Yellow Emperor first used benevolence and righteousness to agitate the human heart. Then Yao and Shun wore away the flesh of their thighs and the hair of their shins to nourish the forms of the world. *Li [Yi] notes: '胈 refers to the white flesh [of the thigh].'* They wearied their five viscera for benevolence and righteousness, strained their blood and qi to model laws and standards. *Guo Qingfan notes: '*Shi Yan* states: "矜 means suffering." Straining their blood and qi is like Mencius’ saying "toil their minds."'* Yet still, they could not prevail. Thus, Yao banished Huan Dou to Mount Chong, exiled the Three Miao to Sanwei, and sent Gong Gong to Youdu—was this not failing to overcome the world?

By the time of the Three Kings, the world was greatly alarmed. *Xuan [Yin] notes: 'Unable to rest in their true nature.'* Below were Jie and Zhi, above were Zeng [zi] and Shi; *Cheng [Xuanying] notes: 'Jie and Zhi practiced the conduct of petty men below, while Zeng and Shi practiced the conduct of noble men above.'* and the Confucians and Mohists all arose. *Arising simultaneously.* Then joy and anger suspected one another, the foolish and wise deceived one another, the good and bad slandered one another, the absurd and truthful mocked one another, and the world declined. *Great virtue was no longer the same; the root of virtue is profoundly unified, yet here its traces diverged.* The true nature of life scattered and dissolved. *Cheng [Xuanying] notes: 'Scattered and dissolved means disordered.'* The world pursued knowledge, and the common people were exhausted in their seeking. *Those above exhausted their intelligence, and the common people could not meet their demands.*"

Thus, axes and saws were applied, inked cords cut, and mallets and chisels split. *Carpenters use inked cords to straighten wood, just as rulers use ritual and law to correct people; carpenters use axes, saws, mallets, and chisels to mar wood, just as rulers use punishments to mar people.* The world became utterly disordered, *where "utterly disordered" means people harmed one another relentlessly.* The fault lay in disturbing the human heart.

Therefore, the worthy took refuge beneath the towering cliffs of great mountains, *Yu Yue notes: "嵁 should be 湛, meaning 'deep.' The mountains are described as great, the cliffs as deep."* while the lords of ten thousand chariots trembled with fear in their ancestral halls. In this age, those executed by decapitation lie heaped upon one another, those in cangues and stocks press close together, and those subjected to punishment and execution stretch as far as the eye can see. *Guangya states: "殊 means 'to sever.'" Cui notes: "桁杨 refers to devices that restrain the neck and shins."* "Lie heaped" refers to the dead; "press close" and "stretch as far as the eye can see" describe their multitude. Yet the Confucians and Mohists now begin to stand on tiptoe and wave their arms amidst the fetters and shackles. Alas! What a lamentable sight.

How extreme it is! How utterly shameless and devoid of shame they are! I know not whether sagely wisdom is not in truth the wedges and clamps of the cangue, *Sima Biao notes: "椄槢 refers to the pegs and blocks of restraint devices."* or whether benevolence and righteousness are not in truth the fetters and shackles with their mortise and tenon. *Cheng Xuanying notes: "凿 means 'hole.' To insert an object into a hole is called 枘."* The cangue employs wedges and clamps as its mechanism; fetters and shackles rely on mortise and tenon for their function. How can one know that Zeng [Can] and Shi [Qiu] are not the whistling arrows of Jie and Robber Zhi? *Xiang Xiu notes: "嚆矢 refers to arrows that whistle in flight."* The *Zilin* states: "嚆 means 'a great cry.'" Guo Xiang notes: "This says that Zeng and Shi are the tools of Jie and Robber Zhi."* Thus it is said: "Cut off sageliness, cast aside wisdom, and the world will be greatly ordered."

The Yellow Emperor, having ruled as Son of Heaven for nineteen years, with his decrees enforced throughout the realm, heard that Master Guangcheng dwelled on Mount Kongtong. *The *Shiwen* notes: "Master Guangcheng—some say he is Laozi. The *Erya* states: 'The region north of the Dipper’s handle is called Kongtong.'" He therefore went to see him and said: "I have heard that you, Master, have attained the utmost Dao. I venture to ask: what is the essence of the utmost Dao? I wish to draw forth the essence of Heaven and Earth to aid the five grains and nourish the people." *Cheng Xuanying notes: "He wished to draw the essential qi of yin and yang to assist the growth of the five grains."* "I further wish to regulate yin and yang to bring all living things to fulfillment." *Cheng Xuanying notes: "He wished to model himself on yin and yang, establishing offices and dividing duties. 遂 means 'to follow.'" "How might this be done?"

Master Guangcheng replied: "What you wish to ask concerns the substance of things; *Cheng Xuanying notes: "而 means 'you.' The same below. What you ask is shallow, no more than form and substance."* what you wish to regulate is the dregs of things." *Xuan Ying notes: "This is to say, the remnants after the uncarved block has been dispersed."* "Since you have governed the world in this way, clouds do not gather before raining, *Sima Biao notes: "族 means 'to gather.' Rain falls before gathering, meaning the moisture is scant."* plants and trees do not yellow before falling, *Sima Biao notes: "This indicates an excess of killing qi."* and the light of the sun and moon grows ever more dim." *Xuan Ying notes: "The qi of Heaven and Earth is withering to this degree."* "As for the mean and cramped heart of a flatterer—how could one speak to you of the utmost Dao?" *Cheng Xuanying notes: "You are a sycophantic man with a narrow and petty heart."* *Li Yi notes: "翦翦 describes something shallow and limited."* *Note: 翦 is interchangeable with 谫.*

The Yellow Emperor withdrew, relinquished the world, built a solitary chamber, spread a mat of white cogon grass to demonstrate purity, and lived in seclusion for three months. Then he went again to seek an audience. *邀 means 'to request.'* Master Guangcheng lay with his head to the south. The Yellow Emperor approached from downwind, crawling on his knees, bowed twice, touched his forehead to the ground, and asked: "I have heard that you, Master, have attained the utmost Dao. I venture to ask: how should one cultivate the body to attain longevity?"

Master Guangcheng rose abruptly—*蹶然 describes a swift rising*—and said: "Excellent, this question! Come!"

"I shall speak to you of the utmost Dao. The essence of the utmost Dao is profound and obscure; its ultimate reaches are dim and silent. See nothing, hear nothing, embrace the spirit in stillness—then the body will right itself. Be still, be pure; do not weary your form, do not disturb your essence, and you may attain longevity."

*Xuan comments: "This speaks of calming the external to nurture the internal."*

Let your eyes see nothing, your ears hear nothing, your heart know nothing—then your spirit will guard your form, and your form will attain long life. Guard your inner self, cut off thoughts and cares. Seal your outer self, cease all movement and action. Excessive knowledge brings ruin."

*Xuan comments: "When inner and outer draw upon each other, the malady lies in knowledge—thus I summarize it."*

"I shall lead you to the heights of Great Clarity, to the source of utmost yang. I shall guide you through the gate of profound obscurity, to the source of utmost yin. *Sui* means 'to reach directly.' The sage’s wisdom illumines like the sun and moon, hence it is called Great Clarity. When moved by sensation, it is said to ascend to the heights of Great Clarity; when free of sensation, deeply rooted and profoundly serene, it is said to enter the gate of profound obscurity. Heaven and Earth have their offices—*Xuan comments: 'The two powers divide their functions'*—yin and yang have their repositories—*Xuan comments: 'Each is the root of the other.'* Guard your body with care, and the myriad things will naturally flourish."

*Xuan comments: "The 'things' here refer to the Dao. Guard the body, and the Dao will receive its nourishment, naturally achieving completion."*

"I guard the One and dwell in harmony—*Xuan comments: 'The harmony of the two qi'*—thus I have cultivated my body for one thousand two hundred years, and my form has never declined."

*Xuan comments: "The way of long life lies in the mutual guardianship of form and spirit."*

The Yellow Emperor bowed twice, touched his forehead to the ground, and said: "Master Guangcheng is what may be called Heaven!"

*Xuan comments: "In virtue, he is one with Heaven."*

Master Guangcheng said: "Come!"

"I shall speak to you. Those things appear boundless, yet people all deem them finite; the Dao moves in cycles, yet people believe it perishes and ends. Those things seem unfathomable, yet people all suppose them limited; the Dao is inherently inexhaustible, yet people believe it has an end. One who attains my Dao may ascend as sovereign above or reign as king below; one who loses my Dao may rise to see light yet sink into dust. Though they behold brightness, they have already become soil.

Now, the hundred flourishing things—all that thrives is called the hundred flourishing—are born from earth and return to earth. *Xuan comments: 'Those who do not know the Dao differ not from mere things!'*

Thus I shall leave you, entering the Gate of the Boundless to wander the Wilds of the Limitless. *Cheng comments: 'Returning to the root of darkness and stillness, entering the Gate of the Boundless; responding to change between heaven and earth, wandering the Wilds of the Limitless.'*

I share radiance with the sun and moon; I am constant with heaven and earth. *Cheng comments: 'Share, meaning the same.'*

Approach me, and all is merged! *Shiwen: 'Merged, meaning blended as one.'* Depart from me, and all is obscured! *Guo Songtao comments: 'Merged and obscured are interchangeable; merged is also obscured. Approach me, turn toward me; depart from me, turn away. Let others turn toward or away—respond with a heart devoid of intent.'*

Let others perish entirely; I alone endure!" *Xuan comments: 'In unceasing union with the Dao.'*

*[Note: The four characters "What is to be done?" are supplied from the *Jishi* edition.*

Cloud General was traveling east—*Chuxue Ji* cites Sima, who says: 'Cloud General, the commander of clouds.'*—when he passed the branch of the *fusang* tree—*Li comments: 'Fusang, a divine tree growing in the Eastern Sea.'*—and happened upon Hong Meng. *Sima comments: 'The primordial qi of nature.'* Hong Meng was just then slapping his thighs and leaping about in play. *Cheng comments: 'Slapping, meaning to strike. Leaping, meaning to jump.'*

Cloud General, seeing him, halted in startled confusion and stood motionless. *Li comments: 'Startled, meaning lost in self-forgetting. Motionless, meaning still and unmoving.'* He asked: "Old sir, who are you? Old sir, what are you doing?"

Hong Meng said, "Wandering."

Cloud General said, "I wish to ask you something."

Hong Meng looked up at Cloud General and exclaimed, "Hmph!"

Cloud General said, "The qi of Heaven is discordant, the qi of Earth is pent-up, the Six Qi are unbalanced—*Cheng comments: 'Yin, yang, wind, rain, darkness, and light.'*—and the Four Seasons are out of order. Now I wish to harmonize the essence of the Six Qi to nurture all living things—*Cheng comments: 'To blend the essence of the Six Qi and nourish the myriad beings.'*—what is to be done?"

Hong Meng slapped his thighs, leapt about, shook his head, and said, "I know not, I know not."

Cloud General could not press his question. Three years later, he traveled east again, passing through the wilds of Song, where he once more encountered Hong Meng. Cloud General rejoiced greatly, hastened forward, and said, "Has Heaven forgotten me? Has Heaven forgotten me?"—*addressing him as "Heaven," as the Yellow Emperor did with Guangchengzi.* He bowed twice, prostrating himself, and wished to hear from Hong Meng.

Hong Meng said, "To wander without seeking, to roam wildly without aim, to find what one seeks of itself, to go where one pleases of itself. The wanderer moves freely—*with the reins in hand, meaning to go forth and wander*—to observe the uncontrived. *Xuan comments: 'The spontaneous movement of true mechanism; I merely follow and behold it.'* What do I know?"

Cloud General said, "I, too, thought myself wild and aimless, yet the people follow wherever I go. I cannot escape their company—*Xuan comments: 'They will not leave me be.'*—now they merely imitate me. *Guo comments: 'They mimic what I do.'* I wish to hear a single word from you."

Hong Meng said, "To disrupt Heaven’s constancy, to oppose the nature of things, is to fail in the mysterious workings of Heaven—*Cheng comments: 'To disorder Heaven’s constant Way, to go against the true nature of things, is to prevent the transformations of nature from coming to fruition.'*—to scatter the herds of beasts and make birds cry out at night. Disaster befalls the grasses and trees, calamity reaches even the crawling creatures. *Shiwen comments: 'Stop (止) is also written as 昆.' Su Yu comments: '止 and 豸 are interchangeable.'* Alas! This is the fault of governing people!" *Shiwen comments: 'Alas (意) is also written as 噫; the same below.' Guo comments: 'Where there are traces of governance, there lies the root of disorder.'*

Cloud General said, "Then what am I to do?"

Hong Meng said, "Alas! How poisonous! *Xuan comments: 'The harm runs deep.'* Away with you, lightly, lightly!"

Cloud General said, "I have met with Heaven’s difficulty; I wish to hear a single word."

Hong Meng said, "Alas! Cultivate the heart. Only the heart should be cultivated. You must dwell in non-action, and things will transform of themselves. *Cheng comments: '徒 means merely.'* Cast off your form, expel your cleverness—*Cheng comments: 'Forget both body and mind.'*—forget all relations and things, so that human ties and the myriad beings all efface their traces. Merge with the vast primordial qi—*Sima comments: '涬溟 is the qi of nature.' Xuan comments: 'Become one with the boundless qi.'*—unbind the heart, release the spirit, and be as though soulless. *Xuan comments: 'Loosen its attachments, free its bonds.' Cheng comments: '魂 refers to the pursuit of knowledge; 莫然 means without knowing, like dead ashes or withered wood.'* The ten thousand things, teeming—*Cheng comments: '云云 means multitudinous.' Su Yu comments: '按: "云云" in the *Laozi* is written as "芸芸," meaning natural and spontaneous.'*—each returns to its root. *Xuan comments: 'Each attains its uncontrived true origin.'* Each returns to its root yet knows it not. Vast and undifferentiated, they remain thus all their lives—*Xuan comments: 'Without opening their knowledge.'*—for if they knew it, they would depart from it. *Cheng comments: 'To employ knowledge is to depart from natural nature.'* Do not question their names, do not pry into their natures—*Xuan comments: 'Things have no inherent names, so I need not ask; they have no inherent natures, so I need not pry.'*—and things will naturally come to life. *Cheng comments: 'Entrusting to spontaneous transformation, things attain their inherent principles.'*

Cloud General said, "Heaven has bestowed virtue upon me, shown me silence, and through my own seeking, I have now attained it." He bowed twice, prostrated himself, rose, took his leave, and departed.

The common people all delight in those who are like themselves and despise those who differ. Those who desire likeness and reject difference do so with the intent of standing above the crowd—*Xuan comments: 'They believe they surpass the multitude and that all should follow them.'*—yet how can those who seek to stand above the crowd ever truly do so? They are not genuinely above the crowd. Relying on the multitude to settle what they have heard, they are not even as reliable as those who gather the skills of the many. *Without unique insight, they merely cling to what they have heard from the crowd and take comfort in it, yet they are less trustworthy than those who collect the skills of the many.* Yet they desire to govern others’ states—this is grasping at the benefits of the Three Kings while blind to their harms. *Xuan comments: 'Yet they wish to govern others’ states with their own views, merely seizing upon the advantages of sagely knowledge and benevolence while ignoring their dangers.'* This is to gamble with others’ states. How often can one gamble and not lose the state? In preserving a state, there is not one chance in ten thousand; in losing it, one failure leads to ten thousand losses. *One matter fails, and ten thousand follow.* Alas! How ignorant are those who hold land! For those who possess land possess a great thing."

Those who possess great things cannot be used by things to govern things. *Guo comments: "If one cannot employ things but is instead employed by them, one is merely a thing—how could one govern things? If one cannot govern things, then one is unworthy of possessing great things."* Yet by not being a thing, one can govern things. *Xuan comments: "If one does not perceive things as things, one transcends them, and thus can master them."* Understanding that those who govern things are not themselves things—how could this apply merely to ruling the world and its people?

They enter and exit the six directions, roam the nine provinces, going alone and coming alone: this is called "possessing alone." Those who possess alone are called the most noble. *Cheng comments: "Those who wish to stand out from the crowd and roam alone—none can match this ability, hence it is called 'possessing alone.' Such people are borne and upheld by the people as their lord, truly the most exalted and noble."*

The teachings of the great person are like a form casting its shadow, a sound echoing its response. When questioned, they answer, exhausting what lies within their heart, becoming the world’s counterpart. *Cheng comments: "The counterpart (配) means a match. The one who first senses is the master; the respondent is the match."* They dwell where there is no echo, *Guo comments: "Abiding in stillness to await things."* and move without fixed direction. *Guo comments: "Adapting to things as they transform."*

They lead you through the tumult of comings and goings, *Yu comments: "The *Shi Gu* states: '适 means to go.' 适复 means to go and return. 挠挠 means chaos. Only the great person can guide those entangled in the world’s chaotic comings and goings, roaming with them in boundlessness."* entering and exiting without obstruction, *Xuan comments: "读若去声 (qù shēng)."* sharing no beginning with the sun. *Cheng comments: "Renewing with the sun, thus without end or beginning."*

They praise the body’s form, aligning with the great unity; they assess its appearance, harmonizing with the multitude, without setting themselves apart. In great unity, there is no self. With no self, how could there be anything possessed? *Guo comments: "The difficulty in the world lies in the self. Once the self is gone, the myriad things are no longer possessed."*

Those who perceive possession are the gentlemen of former times; *Xuan comments: "The so-called enlightened sages of the Three Dynasties."* those who perceive non-possession are friends of heaven and earth.

What is lowly yet cannot be left unengaged are things; what is humble yet cannot be left unaccommodated is the people. Though people and things are humble, one must engage and accommodate them, for turning against their nature brings disorder. What is hidden yet cannot be left undone are affairs; *Guo comments: "Affairs are concealed in others, each acting on its own, so one cannot act alone but must follow and entrust."* what is coarse yet cannot be left unpresented is law; *Cheng comments: "Law refers to teachings. Principles are subtle, laws are coarse, so they are presented accordingly."* what is distant yet cannot be left unoccupied is righteousness; *Cheng comments: "Though righteousness is far from the Dao, if it accords with principle, it must be adopted."* what is close yet cannot be left unexpanded is benevolence; *Cheng comments: "Partial affection is narrow; universal love is great benevolence."* what is regulated yet cannot be left unaccumulated is ritual; *Cheng comments: "积 means to thicken. 节 means ornamentation."* what is centered yet cannot be left unexalted is virtue. *Those who cultivate virtue are in harmony with the world, naturally lofty and far-reaching.*

What is one yet cannot be left unchanged is the Dao; *Cheng comments: "The wondrous origin is a single qi, giving life to the myriad things. It is utterly simple and easy—only the Dao!"* what is spiritual yet cannot be left undone is heaven. Thus, the sage observes heaven without aiding it, *Cheng comments: "The sage observes the wondrous principles of nature, fully complying with things without interfering with their inherent nature."* achieves virtue without encumbrance, emerges from the Dao without scheming, *Guo comments: "Unscheming unity is the essence of simplicity."* meets benevolence without reliance—*Their actions naturally align with benevolence, without depending on it.*—thins righteousness without accumulation, responds to ritual without avoidance, *Yu comments: "讳 is read as 违 (wéi). The *Guang Ya* defines 讳 as 'to avoid.' The *Guo Yu*’s Wei commentary states: '违 means to avoid.' The two words are phonetically close and semantically connected. 不讳 means 'not to avoid.'"* engages with affairs without refusal, equalizes by law without disorder, *Cheng comments: "Following the nature of things, using law to equalize them, thus no disorder arises."* relies on the people without disdain, *Guo comments: "Relying on their self-action, not lightly employing them."* and follows things without abandoning them. *Guo comments: "Following and entrusting them, not abandoning their root."*
