Things cannot be forced, yet they cannot be left undone. *Cheng comments: "What is inherently absent cannot be forced; what is inherent in nature cannot be left undone."* Those who do not understand heaven lack pure virtue; *Cheng comments: "Those who are ignorant of natural principles have shallow and impure virtue."* those who do not comprehend the Dao have no path to proceed. *Cheng comments: "Facing matters like a wall, with no way forward."* Those who do not understand the Dao—how pitiful!

What is the Dao? There is the Dao of heaven and the Dao of humans. The honored, who act without doing, belong to the Dao of heaven; the burdened, who act with effort, belong to the Dao of humans. The ruler follows the Dao of heaven; the minister follows the Dao of humans. The Dao of heaven and the Dao of humans—*Xuan comments: "This passage’s meaning is superficial, its phrasing miscellaneous, and it does not closely align with the chapter’s themes. It does not resemble Zhuangzi’s style; perhaps it is a later addition."*—are vastly distant; this cannot go unexamined.

Case in point: Xuan’s doubt is well-founded. Yet Guo Xiang’s commentary exists, so the received text must already have been thus by the Jin dynasty. 〔The eight characters “the Dao of heaven and the Dao of humans” are supplied from the *Jishi* edition.〕

**Outer Chapters, “Heaven and Earth” (Chapter Twelve)**

Though heaven and earth are vast, their transformation is uniform.
*Guo comments: “Uniform in non-action, for they transform of themselves.”*
Though the myriad things are many, their governance is one.
*Guo comments: “Governance is one when each attains its own fulfillment.”*
Though the masses of people are numerous, their lord is the ruler.
The ruler originates in virtue and is perfected by heaven, rooted in possessing virtue and completed by spontaneity.
Thus it is said: The ancient rulers of the world governed through non-action—this was simply the virtue of heaven.
*Cheng comments: “‘Ancient’ means distant. The sage-kings of remote antiquity governed the world through non-action; this was merely the virtue of spontaneity.”*
*Su Yu comments: “‘Ancient’ forms a complete phrase, with the same meaning as ‘profound virtue’ below.”*

Viewed through the Dao in speech, the rulers of the world are rectified.
*Guo comments: “Those who act without doing naturally become rulers.”*
*Guo Songtao comments: “Speech refers to names. Rectifying the ruler’s name, the world obeys his commands. Thus it is said: ‘Names must be spoken, but their correctness lies in aligning with the Dao.’”*
Viewed through the Dao in distinctions, the duties of ruler and minister are clarified.
*Guo comments: “Each fulfills its proper role: non-action for the superior position, effort for the subordinate.”*
Viewed through the Dao in abilities, the offices of the world are well-ordered.
*Guo comments: “When each office corresponds to its proper ability, there is order.”*
Viewed through the Dao in its all-embracing aspect, the responses of the myriad things are complete.
*Xuan comments: “Responding without limit.”*

Thus, that which penetrates heaven and earth is virtue;
*Guo comments: “When the myriad things all attain their due, heaven and earth are penetrated.”*
that which moves through the myriad things is the Dao.
*Cheng comments: “The supreme principle is unobstructed, freely allowing all things to come and go together—this is called the Dao.”*
*Xuan comments: “‘Dao’ here may be a corruption of ‘meaning.’”*
That which governs people from above is affairs;
*Cheng comments: “When affairs are conducted appropriately, the world is governed.”*
that which possesses skill is technique.
*Guo comments: “Technique is the peripheral application of the myriad things.”*
Technique is encompassed by affairs, affairs by rightness, rightness by virtue, virtue by the Dao, and the Dao by heaven.
*Guo comments: “When the Dao of heaven flows smoothly, root and branch are both unimpeded.”*

Thus it is said: “The ancients who nurtured the world—*nurtured* means to cultivate—had no desires, and the world was sufficient; acted without doing, and the myriad things were transformed; were profound and still, and the hundred clans were settled.”
*Cheng comments: “Laozi said: ‘I value stillness, and the people are naturally rectified.’”*
The Record states: *The “Shiwen” notes: “A book, composed by Laozi.”*
“When one penetrates the One, the myriad affairs are complete.”
*Cheng comments: “The One is the Dao. Affairs arise from principle, and principle necessarily encompasses affairs. The root can embrace the branches, so knowing the One, the myriad affairs are complete. This saying appears in the *Xisheng Jing*.”*
“With the attainment of non-intentionality, ghosts and spirits submit.”
Attainment through non-intentionality means none fail to submit.

The Master said: *Sima comments: “Zhuangzi.” Another version: “Laozi.”*
*X

Within clarity lies illumination, and the myriad things reside in a single storehouse.
*Cheng comments: “Forgetting both self and other.”*
Death and life share the same form.
*Cheng comments: “Oblivious to transformation.”*

The Master said: *The Dao dwells in profound depths, limpid in its purity.*
*Annotation: “The *Guangya* states: ‘*Liáo* describes its clarity.’”*
Without metal or stone, there would be no sound. If metal and stone lack harmony, they do not ring—this too reveals the incipient workings of the Dao. Thus, metal and stone possess sound, yet without striking, they do not resonate. Only through resonance do they respond. Who among the myriad things can fix them in place? Extending this principle, the myriad things respond to resonance without limit—who can fix them in place?

The person of royal virtue moves in plain simplicity, ashamed to be entangled in affairs, embracing simplicity in their passage, disdaining involvement in mundane tasks.
*Su Yu comments: “‘Plain simplicity in passage’ echoes the *Shanmu* chapter’s ‘tranquil in their embodied passage.’ ‘Entangled in affairs’ contrasts with ‘attuned to the numinous’—the character for ‘ashamed’ may be an error.”*
They establish their root and origin, thereby attaining attunement with the numinous. Thus their virtue spreads widely. Once the root and origin are established, wisdom can commune with the numinous, and so their virtue extends broadly. When their heart extends outward, things gather to it. Without resonance, there is no response. Thus, form does not arise without the Dao, and life does not become luminous without virtue.
*Cheng comments: “The Dao enables the arising of the myriad things, hence without the Dao there is no arising. Virtue illumines the root and origin, hence without virtue there is no illumination. As the *Laozi* states: ‘The Dao gives birth to them, virtue nurtures them.’”*
To preserve form and exhaust life, to establish virtue and illuminate the Dao—is this not the person of royal virtue?

Vast and boundless! Suddenly they emerge, abruptly they move, and the myriad things follow them—this is the person of royal virtue.
*Guo comments: “‘Suddenly’ and ‘abruptly’ both describe the unselfconscious responsiveness of such a person.”*
They see within obscurity, hear within silence.
*Xuan comments: “The Dao does not reside in form or circumstance.”*
Within obscurity, they alone perceive the dawn; within silence, they alone hear the harmony.
*Xuan comments: “The Dao is not mere quiescence.”*
Thus, profound beyond profundity, yet able to give rise to things.
*Xuan comments: “Utterly unfathomable, yet things emerge from it.”*
Numinous beyond numinosity, yet able to refine essence. Limitless and without direction, yet its essence cannot be concealed. Therefore, in their engagement with the myriad things, they are utterly without substance yet fulfill all requests, not possessing yet providing without fail. They gallop through time, seizing the essential and returning to their origin; they travel afar, yet their return can be met. Great and small, long and short, distant and near.
*Xuan comments: “‘Distant and near’ should read ‘far and near.’ Great or small, long or short, far or near.”*

The Yellow Emperor roamed north of the Red Waters, ascended the heights of Mount Kunlun, and gazed southward. Upon his return, he lost his Dark Pearl. *Wen Xuan’s commentary on "Guang Juejiao Lun" cites Sima, who says: “The Red Waters are a figurative name. The Dark Pearl is a metaphor for the Dao.”* *Xuan comments: “Red is the luminous color of the south; its north is the realm of the dark. The south is the direction of clarity. Having wandered the dark realm, he could not long abide there, and gazing once more toward the bright, the dark was lost.”*

He sent Knowledge to seek it, but Knowledge could not find it. He sent Li Zhu to seek it, but Li Zhu could not find it. He sent Chi Gou to seek it, but Chi Gou could not find it. *Guo Songtao says: “The *Guangyun* states: ‘Chi is the same as ●.’ ● means sound; Gou means anger, and anger too is sound. The *Jiyun* says ‘Chi Gou means strenuous contention,’ which is apt. Knowledge seeks with spirit, Li Zhu seeks with form and shadow, Chi Gou seeks with sound and reputation—thus the more they seek, the farther they stray. But Vague Image, neither form nor formless, could perceive and obtain it.”*

Then he sent Vague Image, and Vague Image found it. The Yellow Emperor said: “How strange! Could Vague Image alone obtain it?” *Xuan comments: “Seeming to have form yet truly formless—this is what is meant by ‘without mind.’”*

Yao’s teacher was Xu You; Xu You’s teacher was Nie Que; Nie Que’s teacher was Wang Ni; Wang Ni’s teacher was Pi Yi.

Yao asked Xu You: “Could Nie Que be fit to match Heaven? I would enlist Wang Ni to summon him.” Yao wished to yield the world to Nie Que, using Wang Ni to bring him forth.

Xu You said: “How perilous it would be to entrust the world to him! Nie Que’s nature is keen, perceptive, wise, and quick—his talents surpass others. *Cheng comments: “Rui means sagely; gei means nimble; min means swift.”* Yet he also takes the human in place of the Heavenly. *Xuan comments: “He is not purely of Heaven.”* He is precise in forbidding transgressions but does not know whence transgressions arise. *Guo comments: “Transgressions arise from keen perception, and he further employs perception to forbid them—thus his perception grows ever more acute.”* Could such a one match Heaven? He would ride the human and lose the Heavenly. If made Son of Heaven, he would rely entirely on the human and no longer retain his natural nature. He would distinguish self from other, exalt perception and rush like fire, be driven by trifling tasks, *Xuan comments: “Ensnared by petty affairs.”* be bound by things, *Xuan comments: “Constrained by external objects.”* glance in all directions as things respond, *Xuan comments: “Endlessly occupied with responses.”* seek to accommodate every contingency, *Xuan comments: “Striving to conform to every situation.”* and transform with things without constancy. *Xuan comments: “Ever changing with circumstances, unable to remain steadfast.”* How could he be fit to match Heaven? Nevertheless, he has clan and ancestry—*Xuan comments: “Every clan has its founding ancestors.”*—he may serve as father to the multitude but not as father of fathers. *Xuan comments: “The father of fathers is the great ancestor of all transformations, the primal source. Nie Que may be a father to the people, but not the father of all fathers.”* He is the master of order and disorder—*Xuan comments: “Master here means origin.”*—employing perception to govern things, he is the source of both order and chaos. He is the calamity of those who face north, the ruin of those who face south.” *Xuan comments: “He is unfit to be a minister, and unfit to be a ruler.”* *Note: This is spoken to caution Yao, not that Nie Que is truly thus.*

Yao observed the land of Hua. *Sima comments: “A place name.”* The border guard of Hua said: “Ah! The sage!”

The border guard of Hua offered blessings to the sage: “May the sage enjoy long life.” Yao replied: “I decline.”
“May the sage grow wealthy.” Yao replied: “I decline.”
“May the sage have many sons.” Yao replied: “I decline.”
The border guard said: “Long life, wealth, and many sons—these are what all people desire. Why do you alone refuse them?”
Yao said: “Many sons bring many fears; wealth brings many affairs; long life brings many humiliations. These three are not the means to nurture virtue, so I decline.”
The border guard said: “At first, I took you for a sage—now you are merely a gentleman. *Xuan comments: “Now you are like this—you are only fit to be a gentleman.”* Heaven gives birth to the myriad people and must assign them duties. If you have many sons and assign them duties, what fear is there? If you are wealthy and distribute it among others, what affairs remain? The sage dwells like a quail and eats like a fledgling—*Xuan comments: “The quail has no fixed dwelling, meaning he does not seek comfort; the fledgling waits for its mother to feed it, meaning he does not seek satiety.”*—moving like a bird in flight, leaving no trace. *Cheng comments: “In harmony with all things, like a bird’s flight, no visible tracks remain.”* When the world follows the Dao, he flourishes with all things; when the world loses the Dao, he cultivates virtue in seclusion. After a thousand years, weary of the world, he departs to become an immortal, riding the white clouds to the realm of the Emperor. *Cheng comments: “The three afflictions—wealth, long life, and many sons—do not reach him.”* His person remains free from misfortune—what humiliation is there?”
The border guard left, and Yao followed him, saying: “May I ask a question?”
The border guard replied: “Withdraw!”
When Yao governed the world, Bo Cheng Zi Gao was established as a feudal lord.

The *Shiwen* notes: “The *Tongbian Jing* states: ‘Since the opening of heaven and earth, Laozi’s body has undergone one thousand two hundred transformations. In later ages, those who attain the Dao—Bo Cheng Zi Gao is one such.’”

When Yao abdicated to Shun, and Shun to Yu, Bo Cheng Zi Gao resigned his title as feudal lord and took up plowing. Yu went to see him and found him tilling in the fields. Yu hastened to stand downwind and, standing, asked: “In the past, when Yao governed the world, you were established as a feudal lord. Yao abdicated to Shun, and Shun to me, yet you resigned your title to plow. May I ask the reason?”

Zi Gao replied: “In the past, when Yao governed the world, he did not reward, yet the people were encouraged; he did not punish, yet the people stood in awe. Now you reward and punish, and the people grow unkind. Virtue declines from this, punishments arise from this, and the chaos of later ages begins from this. Why do you trouble yourself? Do not hinder my work!” He plowed on, vigorous and unheeding.

The *Shiwen* notes: “阖 (*hé*) is also written as 盍 (*hé*). 落 (*luò*) means ‘to abandon.’ The *Zilin* states: ‘俋俋 (*yìyì*) describes a robust and vigorous appearance.’”

In the Great Beginning, there was Nonbeing—yet it cannot be called Nonbeing. There was Nameless Nonbeing, which may be called Nonbeing but cannot be named. From the arising of the One, there was the One, yet formless. *Xuan* comments: “The Great Ultimate had not yet manifested.” When things obtain this to come into being, it is called virtue. *Xuan* comments: “When things obtain this formless One to come into being, each possesses a Great Ultimate within its nature—hence it is called virtue.” The formless has divisions, yet remains unbroken and continuous—this is called fate. *Xuan* comments: “Though divided into yin and yang, they transform and unite in ceaseless flow—this is heaven’s way of decreeing fate.” When movement lingers, things are born. *Xuan* comments: “Movement is the flowing of creation; when it lingers briefly, a thing is born.” When a thing is complete in its principle of life, it is called form. *Xuan* comments: “When a thing receives this and fulfills its principle of life, it is called form.” When form and body preserve spirit, each has its proper norms—this is called nature. *Cheng* comments: “体 (*tǐ*) means ‘substance.’” *Xuan* comments: “Form bears spirit and preserves it; in its seeing, hearing, speaking, and acting, each follows its proper norms—this is what is called nature. The earlier statement, ‘obtaining this to come into being is called virtue,’ refers to this. That nature is discussed after form is because nature requires form to bear it—hence it is said, ‘form and body preserve spirit.’” When nature is cultivated, it returns to virtue. *Xuan* comments: “When nature is cultivated, it reverts to the One it obtained when formless.” When virtue reaches its utmost, it merges with the Beginning. *Xuan* comments: “When virtue reaches its peak, it merges with the Great Beginning—this is the highest attainment.” Merging is emptiness; emptiness is greatness. *Xuan* comments: “This describes the wondrous state of merging with the Beginning.” When sounds unite in speech, *Xuan* comments: “All voices blend—this is the forgetting of words.” when speech unites, it merges with heaven and earth. *Xuan* comments: “Having forgotten words, one becomes one with heaven and earth.” This merging is profound and subtle. If it appears foolish or dull, *Guo* comments: “This is the merging of sitting in forgetfulness.” it is called mysterious virtue, aligning with the Great Compliance. *Guo* comments: “Virtue is profound, and what it complies with is vast.”

Confucius asked Lao Dan: “There is one who practices the Dao as if imitating others—what is not may be taken as is, what is not so may be taken as so.” *Guo* comments: “As if imitating, forcing the unacceptable to be acceptable, the untrue to be true.” A debater once said: ‘Separating hardness and whiteness is like suspending the cosmos.’” *Cheng* comments: “‘Hardness and whiteness’ refers to Gongsun Long’s *Discourse on the White Horse*. Kong Chuan and his followers rigidly upheld this theory, unmatched in their time. Now the debater claims, ‘I can dissect the theory of hardness and whiteness, not treating it as debate—like suspending the sun and moon in the cosmos.’” If this is so, can such a one be called a sage?”

Lao Dan replied: “This is a petty clerk bound by skill, one who wearies the body and startles the mind. (See the *Responding to Emperors and Kings* chapter.) A hound skilled in seizing prey is kept on a leash and grows anxious; a nimble gibbon is brought from the mountains. *Shiwen* notes: “执留 (*zhíliú*) is also written as ●, or as 狸 (*lí*). Sima [Biao] comments: ‘● is a bamboo rat.’ Another says: ‘A hound called *zhíliú* is kept leashed because of its skill, growing sorrowful.’ According to the *Shuowen*: ‘● is a bamboo rat.’ The *Piya* states: ‘It is also called a bamboo ●.’ Guo Pu’s commentary on the *Shanhaijing* cites ‘its sound like a *liú* ox’ as evidence for ‘执留之狗’ in this passage. From this, we know *liú* refers to the *liú* ox, not the bamboo rat—though the bamboo rat’s sound resembles the *liú* ox. The *liú* ox is the *lí* ox, with *liú* and *lí* being homophonous. The *lí* ox is large, like the ‘clouds hanging from the sky’ in the *Free and Easy Wandering* chapter. This hound alone can seize it—hence it is called the *zhíliú* hound. The point is that the hound, though skilled, is leashed and grows sorrowful, while the gibbon, though nimble, is brought from the mountains—both are constrained. The *Responding to Emperors and Kings* chapter quotes Laozi: ‘The nimbleness of gibbons and the hound that seizes the *lí* ox come to be harnessed’—slightly different from this passage, but the meaning is largely the same, further proving that *liú* and *lí* are the same character.’”

“Alas! I tell you what you cannot hear, and what you cannot speak.”

“This refers to the Dao. *Ruò* and *ér*—both mean ‘you.’ All those with heads and feet but without minds or ears are many.” Xuān comments: “Those embodied as humans yet without knowledge or perception are all such.” “Those with form and those without form or shape yet all existing—none remain. Those with form are humans; those without form or shape are the Dao. None can exist with both human and Dao. Their movement is cessation; their death is life; their decline is rise. Yet these are not the *therefore* of it. Motion and stillness, life and death, flourishing and decay—none are where the Dao resides. There is governance in humans.” Sū Yǔ comments: “The Dao cannot be named; only the traces of its transformative influence remain in humans.” “Forget things, forget Heaven—this is called forgetting the self. Having forgotten things, one forgets even the natural Heaven—this is what is meant by forgetting the self. Those who forget the self are said to enter into Heaven.” Xuān comments: “To become one with Heaven.”

Jiānglǘ Miǎn visited Jì Chè and said—*Shìwén* notes: “*Jiāng* is also written as *Jiǎng*. *Miǎn* is also written as *Tù*, pronounced *miǎn*. The surname is Jiānglǘ, the given name Tù. Alternatively, the surname is Jiǎng, the given name Lǘmiǎn.” Jì Chè was a person’s name, likely of the Jì clan—“The Lord of Lǔ said to Miǎn: ‘I request to receive your teaching.’ He declined but could not refuse the command. Having already spoken, he was unsure whether it had struck true, so he asked to try presenting it again. *Cháng* means ‘to test’; *jiàn* means ‘to advance.’ I shall advance it to Chè. I said to the Lord of Lǔ: ‘You must practice reverence and frugality as if donning them. Uproot the ranks of public-spirited loyalty—*shǔ* means ‘category’—and let no partiality or self-interest remain, so that governance is without private bias. Then which of the people would dare not be harmonious?’” *Jí* means ‘harmony.’

Jì Chè laughed heartily and said: “As for your master’s words, regarding the virtue of emperors and kings, it is like the mantis raising its arms in anger to block a chariot’s wheel-tracks—it will surely not suffice. Moreover, if this is so, then one’s self-cultivation will be perilous, not a path to self-security.”

"The observation terrace teems with affairs—*guān tái* refers to the lord’s dwelling-place; *wù* means ‘matters.’ This means that from the lord’s vantage, matters multiply. Those who come to follow in his steps will be many." Where footsteps gather, the lord will soon be overwhelmed by their burdens, which is no way for an emperor or king to cultivate virtue and bring peace to the people.

Jiǎng Lǘ Miǎn started in alarm, saying: "*Miǎn* is utterly at a loss before your master’s words." *Xì xì* describes his startled demeanor. Though bewildered, he said: "Nevertheless, I beg you to speak of its general outline." *Fēng* is read as *fán*, meaning "to speak of its essentials." Since *fēng* originally derives from *fán*, the two may be used interchangeably.

Jì Chè said: "When the great sage governs the world, he stirs and sways the people’s hearts, transforming their education and altering their customs. He wholly eradicates their predatory impulses and advances their singular resolve. The people follow as if by their own nature, unaware of how this comes to be. In this way, does he defer to the teachings of Yao and Shun as if they were his elder brothers? Rather, he regards them as so much dross. He does not yield precedence to Yao and Shun but seeks to align all under a single virtue, his heart at rest in non-action."

When Zigong journeyed south to Chu and returned to Jin, he passed through Hanyin. There he saw an old man tending his garden beds—Li [Yi] notes: "Vegetables are called *pu*; bordered plots are called *qi*"—who had dug a tunnel to enter a well. Carrying a jar, he emerged to water his plants, straining laboriously yet accomplishing little. Guo [Xiang] observes: "*Huhu* describes the appearance of exertion."

Zigong said: "Here is a device that can irrigate a hundred beds in a single day, requiring little effort yet yielding great results. Would you not desire it?"

The gardener raised his head to look at him and asked: "How does it work?"

Zigong replied: "Carve wood into a mechanism, heavier at the rear and lighter at the front, which draws water as if by pulling. Its speed resembles boiling water overflowing."—Li [Yi] notes: "*Chou* means 'to draw.'" The *Shiwen* reads: "*Shu* is pronounced *suǒjué*; *yi* may also be written as *yì*. Li [Yi] says: 'As swift as boiling water spilling over.'"—"It is called a *gao*." The *Shiwen* reads: "May also be written as *qiao*; Sima [Biao] and Li [Yi] say: 'It is a well-sweep.'"

The gardener flushed with anger, his expression turning to scornful laughter as he said: "I have heard from my teacher: 'One who possesses mechanical devices must engage in mechanical affairs; one who engages in mechanical affairs must harbor a mechanical heart.' When a mechanical heart lodges in the breast, purity and whiteness are incomplete. When purity and whiteness are incomplete, the spirit’s life is unsettled. One whose spirit’s life is unsettled cannot be borne by the Dao. *Sheng* and *xing* are synonymous; this means one cannot be a vessel for the Dao. I am not ignorant of such things—I am ashamed to do them."

Zigong hung his head in shame, his face downcast, and made no reply.

After a pause, the gardener asked, "What are you doing here?"

"I am a disciple of Kong Qiu," Zigong replied.

The gardener said, "Are you not one of those who amass vast learning to rival the sages, who strut about to overshadow the masses—who sing mournful songs on a single string to peddle your reputation across the world? You should be forgetting your *qi* and spirit, casting off your bodily form, and nearing the Way! Yet you cannot even govern yourself—how could you presume to govern the world? Be gone! Do not hinder my work."

Zigong shrank back, his face pale with shame, and felt utterly discomposed. He walked thirty *li* before recovering.

His disciples asked, "Who was that man just now? Why did you change countenance and lose composure before him, remaining unsettled for the rest of the day?"

Zigong said, "At first, I thought there was only one such man in the world. I did not know there was another like him. I heard from the Master: 'In affairs, seek what is fitting; in work, seek completion; expend little effort yet achieve much—this is the Way of the sage.' But this man is not so. One who holds to the Dao has complete virtue; one with complete virtue has a complete form; one with a complete form has complete spirit. One with complete spirit—this is the Way of the sage. He entrusts his life to the world and walks among the people, yet does not know where he is headed. How boundless and perfectly whole he is! How unfathomable his attainment."

"One must utterly forget the heart that pursues merit, profit, cunning, and artifice. Such a heart is free of these four burdens. As for such a person, if his aspiration does not tend toward it, his heart will not act upon it. Though the world praises him and he attains what he deems right—what is called complete virtue—he remains aloof and unmoved. Though the world condemns him and he loses what he deems right, he remains indifferent and unshaken. The world’s praise or blame neither benefits nor harms him; this is the person of complete virtue!

Yet I am one tossed by the winds and waves." Returning to Lu, he told Confucius what had transpired.

Confucius said, "That man merely dabbles in the arts of the Primal Chaos: he recognizes one aspect but not the other. He cultivates his inner self but neglects his outer conduct. One who is lucid and returns to simplicity, who acts without contrivance and restores primal integrity, who embraces spirit and roams among the common folk—would you truly be so astonished? As for the arts of Primal Chaos, how could you or I presume to comprehend them?"

Hun Mang was traveling east to the Great Abyss, the sea. Along the eastern shore, he encountered Yuan Feng.

Yuan Feng asked, "Where are you going?"
"I am going to the Great Abyss."
"What will you do there?"
"The Great Abyss is such a thing: it receives all yet never fills, it is drawn from yet never exhausted. I will wander there."

Yuan Feng said, "Do you have no concern for the horizontal-eyed people? I would hear of sagely governance."

Hun Mang replied:

"As for sagely governance: offices are established and measures enacted without failing their proper purpose. Sima Biao notes: 'In administering policies and spreading teachings, each attains its fitting place.' Those raised to office never fall short of their capacities. All circumstances are fully discerned, and actions proceed accordingly. Wang Xian comments: 'All conditions and principles are thoroughly perceived, then followed in action.' Words and deeds arise from one’s own nature, and the world is transformed thereby. By personally enacting one’s words, all take self-cultivation as their model, and the people are thereby transformed. With a wave of the hand or a glance of direction, the people of the four directions all come as one. This is to say: with a gesture of the hand or a turn of the head, the people all follow without exception. Sima Biao explains: '挠 means to move.' Guo Qingfan notes: '顾指 refers to turning toward someone and directing them. Zuo Si’s *Rhapsody on the Capital of Wu* reads, "Grasping banners as if at a glance and command," where Liu Kui annotates: "It means to direct as one wishes."' This is what is called sagely governance."

"Now I would hear of the Powered Person."

The Master said: "The Powered Person dwells without thought, moves without deliberation, and harbors no judgments of right or wrong, beauty or ugliness. Wang Xian comments: 'The mind passes over things without lingering.' Within the four seas, all share in the benefits—this is called joy; all are provided for—this is called peace. The people and their ruler together know joy and peace. (The words 为 and 谓 are interchangeable here.) They are forlorn as an infant who has lost its mother, disoriented as a traveler who has lost the Way. The *Shiwen* notes: 'The *Zilin* states: "怊 means dismay."' (Note: 傥 signifies a mind without anchor.) The people depend upon them thus. Their resources are abundant, yet they know not whence they come; their food and drink are sufficient, yet they know not from where they derive. Cheng Xuanying explains: 'With few desires and contentment in their lot, their resources are ever abundant; without craving for rich flavors, their food and drink are always sufficient.' This is called the bearing of the Powered Person." Guo Xiang comments: "Power is the trace of the spirit-person, hence it is called *bearing*."

"Now I would hear of the Spirit Person."

The Master said: "The supreme spirit rides upon light, vanishing with form. The highest grade of spirit-person rides the radiance of illumination, leaving no trace of form. This is called *illuminating the vast*. Cheng Xuanying explains: 'Their wisdom encompasses the ten thousand things, their clarity surpasses the three luminaries; no darkness remains unlit—how could this not be vast!' Yao notes: 'The Jin people avoided the taboo on 昭, writing it as 照 throughout. The *Model Calligraphy of the Right Army* is all thus. Those who do not know this have mistakenly interpreted the character as 照, which is incorrect.' They fulfill destiny and exhaust reality. Wang Xian comments: 'They fulfill Heaven’s mandate and exhaust the true principle.' Heaven and Earth rejoice, and the ten thousand affairs dissolve away. Wang Xian comments: 'They rejoice with Heaven and Earth, and all worldly burdens are cast off.' The ten thousand things return to their true nature, their essential reality made equal. This is called *merging with the Dark Obscurity*." (That is, merging with the profound and obscure.)

Menwugui (the text reads "Men Wuwei" in the Sima Biao edition) and Chizhang Manji—Wang Xian comments: "Chizhang is the surname, Manji the given name"—observed the armies of King Wu.

This refers to the campaign at Mengjin. Chizhang Manji said: "Are they not inferior to the Yu clan? Thus they encountered this calamity." Inferior to the Yu clan’s yielding abdication, they suffered the disaster of armed conquest.

Menwugui said: "Was the world already well-governed when the Yu clan ruled it, or did they restore order only after chaos?" That is, was the world already at peace when the Yu clan took charge, or did they govern only after turmoil had arisen?

Chizhang Manji said: "The universal wish is for a world equally well-governed—why calculate the Yu clan’s merits? Guo Xiang comments: 'When governance is equal, each wish is fulfilled; why deliberate the Yu clan’s virtue and elevate them as rulers?' The Yu clan were physicians for sores." Li Yi comments: "疡 means head wounds." Wang Yinzhi notes: "药 was anciently read as *yao*, akin in sound and meaning to *liao* (to heal). The *Fangyan* states: '疗 means to treat.'" Guo Xiang comments: "The world all suffered from wounds and disorder, so they sought the Yu clan to heal them." Like shaving the bald or calling the doctor when ill. Xuan Ying comments: "Why shave if not bald? Why call a doctor if not ill?"

The filial son prepares medicine to treat his compassionate father, yet his countenance is scorched with distress—the sage feels shame at this. Xuan Ying comments: "This shows it is better to nurture one’s kin so they do not fall ill."

In an age of perfect governance, worthies are not exalted, nor are the capable employed. The high are like treetops—branches without intent, simply existing above. The people are like wild deer. Guo Xiang comments: "Free and self-content." Upright yet unaware of righteousness, naturally fitting. Loving one another yet unaware of benevolence; genuine yet unaware of loyalty; fitting yet unaware of trust. Cheng Xuanying comments: "Acting truly and fittingly." Moving and employing one another, yet not considering it a favor. They serve each other mutually, so no gratitude is owed.

Thus, actions leave no traces, and affairs leave no records. Cheng Xuanying comments: "Acting according to nature, no traces remain. With traces obscured, affairs too vanish." The old edition treats "no records" as a separate passage, and this follows that.

The filial son does not flatter his parents; the loyal minister does not fawn upon his lord—this is the height of ministerial and filial conduct. Yet if he agrees with all his parent’s words and deems all their actions good, the world calls him an unworthy son. If he agrees with all his lord’s words and deems all their actions good, the world calls him an unworthy minister. Yet how can one be certain this is not the inevitable way of things? Xuan Ying comments: "They condemn flattery in ministers and sons but fail to see that human nature makes it inevitable."

When the world deems what it calls right as right and what it calls good as good, it does not call such people flatterers—it aligns with them in meaning. Guo Qingfan notes: "道 here means flattery. In the *Yufu* chapter: 'Echoing words to curry favor is called flattery.' In Xunzi’s *Bu Gou* chapter, 'not flattering' appears as '道谀' in the *Han Shi Waizhuan*—道 and 谄 are homophonous variants." Xuan Ying comments: "The world clearly engages in flattery yet does not name it as such."

Does this mean the world holds its own customs as stricter than kinship and more revered than lordship? Xuan Ying comments: "They condemn flattery toward lords and kin but accept it toward worldly customs—does this mean worldly customs are even stricter and more revered?"

Call a man a flatterer of others, and he flushes with anger; call him a sycophant, and he bristles. Yet all his life he flatters others, all his life he sycophants. Xuan Ying comments: "He detests the name but delights in the act." He gathers analogies, adorns his words, and assembles crowds. Xuan Ying comments: "He broadens analogies to make things clear, refines his language to move listeners—this is how he draws people to himself." Thus, his beginning and end, root and branch, do not implicate one another. Xuan Ying comments: "He indulges in the act yet bears no blame—hence they do not implicate each other."

He drapes robes, displays colors, alters his expression to ingratiate himself with the age, yet does not call himself a flatterer—referring to the lord. He joins the company of such men, aligns with their judgments, yet does not consider himself one of the crowd. Xuan Ying comments: "He mingles with the crowd, adopts their ways, yet still believes himself distinct from them." This is the height of folly.

One who recognizes his folly is not utterly foolish; one who recognizes his confusion is not utterly confused. The utterly confused never awaken their whole lives; the utterly foolish never gain insight. Sima Biao comments: "灵 means understanding."

If three walk together and one is confused, the destination may still be reached—for the confused are few. If two are confused, the journey is laborious and the destination unattained—for the confused prevail. Now, with the whole world confused, though I have prayers and aspirations, they cannot be fulfilled. Is this not lamentable?

The grand music does not enter the ears of the village folk—*Sima* comments: "Grand music refers to the melodies of *Xianchi* and the six tones."—while the tunes of *Zheyang* and *Huanghua*—*Cheng* notes: "These are likely popular minor folk tunes of antiquity." *Shiwen* adds: "*Huang* is also written as *hua*, pronounced like 'flower.'"—elicit only gaping laughter. *Li* explains: "*Ke* denotes the sound of laughter."

Thus, lofty words do not lodge in the hearts of the common crowd—*Xuan* observes: "They do not penetrate." *Cheng* adds: "Words that transcend the vulgar are called lofty."—and the profound speech does not emerge, for vulgar speech prevails. *Cheng* notes: "Emerge means to become manifest."

To be misled by two *fou*-*zhong* measures is to lose one’s way. *Shiwen* reads: "*Fou* should be *chui*; *zhong* should be *zhong*. This speaks of dangling one’s feet in the air, making any destination unattainable." *Sima’s* version has "two *chui zhong*," explaining: "*Zhong* means to fix one’s attention." *Guo Songtao* comments: "*Shuowen* defines *fou* as a clay vessel for holding wine or broth, and *zhong* as a wine vessel. *Xiao Erya* states: 'Two and a half *fu* make a *sou*, two and a half *sou* make a *fou*, and two *fou* make a *zhong*.' Both *fou* and *zhong* are measuring vessels: a *fou* holds four *hu*, and a *zhong* holds eight. To be misled by two *fou* and *zhong* is to fail to distinguish their capacities, and using them as measures leaves one utterly at a loss. The earlier passage speaks of 'one confused' and 'two confused' in terms of people; here, 'misled by two *fou*-*zhong*' refers to matters." *Guo’s* annotation reads: "Each clings to their own certainty, hence they know not where to go." His interpretation likely aligns with the present text. *Lu* altered "*fou*-*zhong*" to "*chui*-*zhong*," and *Cheng’s* commentary followed, though the explanation remains unsatisfactory. *Yu* changed "two *fou*-*zhong*" to "one *qi*-*zhong*," altering even more characters, and his approach is no better than *Guo’s* gloss of the text as it stands.

Now, with the whole world confused, though I have prayers and aspirations, how could they be realized? To know they cannot be realized yet still insist on them is yet another confusion. Thus, it is better to let go and not pursue them. *Xuan* explains: "Not pursue means not to delve into them." If one does not pursue, who then shares the sorrow? *Cheng* notes: "*Bi* means to join with." This is a word of self-consolation.

A leper gives birth in the dead of night and hastily fetches a light to examine the child, anxiously fearing it will resemble him. *Xuan* comments: "A leper dreads his child’s likeness to himself. Now, knowing the world’s confusion, if I insist on what cannot be achieved and thereby add another confusion, am I not equally afraid of resemblance? Thus, it is better to let go and distance oneself from sorrow, for one fears only falling into the same confusion."

A century-old tree is felled to carve sacrificial goblets—*Huainan’s* *Chuzhen* commentary notes: "Sacrificial goblets are intricately carved vessels."—adorned with blue and yellow patterns, yet its severed trunk lies discarded in a ditch. Comparing the sacrificial goblet to the discarded trunk in the ditch, beauty and ugliness differ, yet their loss of nature is the same. Robber Zhi and the virtuous Zeng and Shi differ in conduct, yet their loss of nature is equal.

Moreover, there are five ways to lose one’s nature:
First, the five colors confuse the eyes, making them unclear;
Second, the five sounds confuse the ears, making them dull;
Third, the five odors—*Cheng* explains: "The five odors are rank, scorched, fragrant, fishy, and rotten."—foul the nose, congesting and injuring the nasal bridge—*Cheng* adds: "The five odors overwhelm the nose, causing blockage and harm to the forehead." *External texts* call fragrance *chou*, hence the *Yi* says, "Its fragrance is like orchid"; the *Daoist scriptures* speak of "five fragrances," and the *Xisheng Jing* states, "Fragrance and flavor are bondage." *Shiwen* reads: "*Zong* is pronounced *zigong*, and *Guo* reads it as *jun*."
Fourth, the five flavors muddle the mouth, making it diseased and impaired—*Guo Qingfan* notes: "*Shi Jing*’s *Si Qi* commentary says, '*Li* means illness.' *Guang Ya* states, '*Shuang* means injury.' This speaks of harming the sense of taste."
Fifth, preferences and aversions agitate the heart, making the nature restless—*Cheng* explains: "*Qu* means to seek; *hua* means to confuse."

These five are all harms to life. Yet Yang and Mo, standing apart and striving, believe themselves to have attained something—this is not what I call attainment. To be "attained" yet trapped—*Cheng* notes: "Having falsified one’s nature, one encounters hardship."—can this be called attainment? Then the owl in a cage may also be called attainment. Moreover, chasing after sounds and colors to barricade the inner self—like firewood blocking a path—and adorning the outer self with fur caps, kingfisher-feathered crowns, sashes, tablets, and long belts—*Cheng* explains: "A *pi-bian* is a cap made of fur. A *yu-guan* is a crown adorned with kingfisher feathers. *Jin* means to tuck; *hu* is like a jade tablet. *Shen* is a sash. *Xiu* means long."—the inner self is propped up like a cage—*Cheng* notes: "The cage is a pen. Supports fill the inside, hence it is likened to a cage."—while the outer self is bound by ropes and snares—*Shiwen* reads: "*Zhong* is pronounced *zhilong*." *Cheng* adds: "*Mo-jiao* are ropes."—peering about within ropes and snares—*Cheng* explains: "*Huan-huan* describes the gaze."—and believing oneself attained, is like a criminal with arms bound behind his back—*Sima* notes: "Bound arms means tied behind the back." *Xuan* adds: "And fingers locked together."

While tigers and leopards are confined in sacks and cages, one might also deem this "attainment."

The Dao of Heaven moves without accumulation, thus the myriad things come to be. *Shiwen* notes: "Accumulation refers to stagnation and obstruction." The Dao of the Emperor moves without accumulation, thus the world submits. *Xuan* comments: "Spirit and transformation are as one." The Dao of the Sage moves without accumulation, thus all within the seas obeys. *Xuan* adds: "Utmost sincerity knows no pause."

Those who comprehend Heaven, penetrate the Sage, and master the six penetrations and four openings of the Emperor-King’s virtue—when they act for themselves, all is dimly still. *Shiwen* explains: "The six penetrations are yin, yang, wind, rain, darkness, and brightness. The four openings are the four directions." *Cheng* elaborates: "The six penetrations refer to the four directions and above and below. The four openings refer to the four seasons. Allowing things to move of themselves is called 'acting for oneself.' Concealing one’s traces and veiling one’s brilliance, one remains as if obscure; yet movement does not disturb stillness, hence all is still."

The stillness of the Sage is not because stillness is good and thus pursued. The myriad things cannot disturb the heart, hence it is still. *Nao* is a loan character for *nao* (to disturb). When water is still, it clearly reflects beard and brows, and its level serves as a standard—great craftsmen take it as their model. Its levelness and standard align, so artisans adopt it, calling it the "water level." *Zhong* is pronounced *zhú-zhòng*.

If still water is so clear, how much more so the spirit! Its clarity is all the more evident. When the Sage’s heart is still, it is the mirror of Heaven and Earth, the mirror of the myriad things. If truly still, even the essence of Heaven and Earth and the principles of the myriad things cannot escape it.

Now, emptiness, stillness, tranquility, and non-action are the level of Heaven and Earth and the ultimate of the Dao and virtue. Thus, Emperors, Kings, and Sages rest therein. *Xuan* comments: "They repose their hearts in this." Resting leads to emptiness; emptiness leads to fullness; fullness leads to order. When the heart rests, it unites with emptiness in virtue; united with emptiness, it embraces all principles; embracing all principles, all is well-ordered and in sequence.

Emptiness leads to stillness; stillness leads to movement; movement leads to attainment. Only through emptiness can one achieve stillness; stillness enables movement; movement brings proper attainment. Stillness means non-action; through non-action, those charged with affairs take responsibility. In stillness and non-action, one does not disturb the multitude, so those tasked with affairs govern themselves.

Through non-action, one attains *yuyu*; *yuyu* means cares and troubles cannot abide, and lifespan is long. *Shiwen* cites the *Guangya*: "*Yuyu* means joy." *Xuan* adds: "External troubles cannot reside in the heart, hence the spirit is at ease and life is long."

Emptiness, stillness, tranquility, and non-action are the root of the myriad things. Practicing this while facing south, one becomes a ruler like Yao; practicing this while facing north, one becomes a minister like Shun. Applying this in a superior position is the virtue of Emperors, Kings, and the Son of Heaven; applying this in a subordinate position is the Dao of the Mysterious Sage and Unadorned King. *Cheng* explains: "Those who possess the Dao but lack the rank are called Mysterious Sages and Unadorned Kings—those who esteem themselves, such as Laozi and Confucius." *Yao* notes: "The term 'Unadorned King’s Twelve Classics' is a later usage."

Retiring to leisurely wander with this, recluses of rivers, seas, mountains, and forests submit. *Cheng* comments: "Such as the likes of Chao and Xu." Advancing to govern and pacify the world with this, one’s achievements are great, fame is illustrious, and the world is unified. *Guo* states: "The scope of non-action is vast—where in the world is it not non-action? Thus, if the ruler does not take on the duties of the chief minister, then Yi and Lü remain still in their offices; if the chief minister does not interfere with the hundred officials, then the hundred officials remain still in their governance; if the hundred officials do not burden the myriad people, then the myriad people remain still and secure in their occupations; if the myriad people do not alter their respective abilities, then the world’s self and others remain still and self-content. From the Son of Heaven to the common people, who can achieve anything through action? Thus, the more non-action, the more revered."

Still, one is a Sage; in motion, one is a King. Through non-action, one is revered; through simplicity, the world cannot compete in beauty. Though utterly simple, it is naturally the utmost beauty.

Those who comprehend the virtue of Heaven and Earth call this the Great Root and Great Origin, harmonizing with Heaven. *Guo* explains: "Heaven and Earth take non-action as their virtue; thus, understanding their origin and root means no opposition to Heaven." This is how one harmonizes and regulates the world, harmonizing with others. *Guo* adds: "Following Heaven is how one responds to others; thus, when Heaven’s harmony is complete, human harmony is fulfilled." *Cheng* notes: "*Jun* means to level; *tiao* means to follow."

Harmonizing with others is called human joy; harmonizing with Heaven is called Heavenly joy. *Cheng* comments: "Bending to the dust of the world below, rising to accord with nature above."

Zhuangzi said: "O my Master! O my Master! You pulverize the myriad things yet are not cruel, bestow blessings for ten thousand generations yet are not benevolent, are older than high antiquity yet are not long-lived, cover and support Heaven and Earth, carve and sculpt the forms of things yet are not crafty." *Guo* explains: "Longevity refers only to the length of time; without limit, there is nothing to call long-lived." *Note*: These six phrases also appear in the "Great and Venerable Teacher" chapter. In that text, "cruel" is written as "righteous," where righteousness resembles autumn’s killing; "long-lived" is written as "old," with the same meaning.

This is called Heavenly joy.

Thus it is said: Those who know Heavenly joy—while alive, their movement follows Heaven’s course; in death, they transform with things. *Cheng* comments: "In life, they harmonize with Heaven’s four seasons; in death, they merge with the transformations of the myriad things." Still, they share virtue with yin; in motion, they ride the same wave as yang. *These four phrases also appear in the "Engraving Intent" chapter.*

Thus those who know Heavenly joy bear no resentment from Heaven, no blame from others, no burden from things, no reproach from spirits. *These four phrases also appear in the "Engraving Intent" chapter. "Resentment" is written as "disaster" in that text.*

Thus it is said: In movement, they are like Heaven; in stillness, they are like Earth. Though movement and stillness differ, with no mind they are one. With a single settled mind, they rule all under Heaven. Their spirits bring no misfortune—*Li* explains: "Misfortune here means calamity."—their souls do not weary. *This phrase also appears in the "Engraving Intent" chapter.* With a single settled mind, the myriad things submit. To extend emptiness and stillness to Heaven and Earth, to penetrate the myriad things—this is called Heavenly joy. Heavenly joy is the heart-mind of the sage, with which they nurture all under Heaven. *To nurture means to cultivate.*

Now, the virtue of emperors and kings takes Heaven and Earth as its model, the Dao and virtue as its master, and non-action as its constant. Through non-action, they employ all under Heaven with abundance; through action, they are employed by all under Heaven with insufficiency. Thus the ancients prized non-action.

When those above practice non-action, those below also practice non-action—this is when those below share virtue with those above. When those below share virtue with those above, they do not serve as ministers. *Cheng* comments: "When above and below practice non-action, ministers usurp the ruler’s virtue." When those below act, those above also act—this is when those above share the Way with those below. When those above share the Way with those below, they do not serve as lords. Those above must practice non-action to employ all under Heaven; those below must act to be employed by all under Heaven. This is the unchanging Dao. *This discourse contains profound principles; it is not empty talk.*

Thus the ancient rulers of all under Heaven, though their knowledge encompassed Heaven and Earth, did not scheme for themselves—*Knowledge here means wisdom; encompassed means enveloped.* *Cheng* comments: "The Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors of remote antiquity, whose wisdom illuminated the two poles, yet ruled with folded arms and non-action, entrusting matters to their ministers—thus they did not scheme for themselves." Though their eloquence adorned the myriad things, they did not speak of themselves. *Cheng* comments: "Though their vast eloquence flowed like a river, adorning the myriad things, they never spoke of themselves." Though their abilities exhausted all within the seas, they did not act for themselves. *Cheng* comments: "Though their talents surpassed all within the seas, what did they do? Thus Laozi said: 'This is called employing the strength of others.'"

Heaven does not produce, yet the myriad things transform; Earth does not grow, yet the myriad things are nurtured; emperors and kings practice non-action, yet all under Heaven achieves success. *Wang Niansun* comments: "*Er Ya* defines *success* as completion. *The Doctrine of the Mean* says: 'They complete without acting.'"*

Thus it is said: Nothing is as spirit-like as Heaven, as abundant as Earth, as great as emperors and kings. Thus it is said: The virtue of emperors and kings matches Heaven and Earth. This is the Way of riding Heaven and Earth, driving the myriad things, and employing the multitudes of people.

Its root lies above, its branches below; its essentials lie with the ruler, its details with the ministers. The deployment of the three armies and the five weapons—*Cheng* comments: "The five weapons are the bow, staff, spear, dagger-axe, and halberd. Deployment means movement."—is the outermost expression of virtue. Rewards and punishments, benefits and harms, the application of the five punishments—*Cheng* comments: "Application means law."—are the outermost expression of teaching. Rituals, laws, measures, and standards; forms, names, comparisons, and investigations—*Explanation of Text* notes: "Comparisons mean assessments; investigations mean examinations."—are the outermost expression of governance. The sounds of bells and drums, the postures of feathers and plumes, are the outermost expression of music. Wailing and weeping, mourning garments, the gradations of mourning attire—*Cheng* comments: "Gradations refer to the five degrees of mourning garments, each with its own distinctions. These are the external forms of teaching."—are the outermost expression of grief.

These five outer expressions require the movement of spirit and the activation of the heart’s artifice before they may follow. Such outer expressions were known to the ancients, yet they were not what came first.

*Cheng* comments: "The ancients refer to those of the middle antiquity. *First* means the root."

The lord precedes and the minister follows; the father precedes and the son follows; the elder brother precedes and the younger follows; the elder precedes and the younger follows; the man precedes and the woman follows; the husband precedes and the wife follows. This precedence of honor and humility, of before and after, is the course of Heaven and Earth, and thus the sage takes it as a model. Heaven is honored and Earth is humble—this is the proper position of the numinous. Spring and summer come first, autumn and winter follow—this is the sequence of the four seasons. The transformations and creations of the myriad things, their sprouting and differentiation into forms, *Cheng* comments: "The first signs of growth are distinct, each with its own shape."—these are the flowing currents of flourishing and decline. The gradations of flourishing and decline are the flowing currents of transformation. Though Heaven and Earth are most numinous, they still possess this sequence of honor and humility, of before and after—how much more so the human Dao!

In ancestral temples, kinship is honored; in court, honor is honored; in village communities, seniority is honored; in conduct, worthiness is honored—this is the sequence of the Great Dao. *Cheng* comments: "The necessary principle of reason."

To speak of the Dao while violating its sequence is not the Dao; to speak of the Dao while not embodying the Dao—how can one claim to grasp the Dao! *Cheng* comments: "Having lost its sequence, it is unfit to govern things."

Thus, those of old who illuminated the Great Dao first illuminated Heaven, and the Dao and its virtue followed. *Cheng* comments: "Nature is the root of the Dao and virtue, hence they follow." Once the Dao and its virtue were illuminated, benevolence and righteousness followed. *Xuan* comments: "Benevolence and righteousness are the threads of the Dao and virtue." Once benevolence and righteousness were illuminated, the division of duties followed—superiors and inferiors had their distinctions, and all offices had their responsibilities. Once the division of duties was illuminated, forms and names followed. *Xuan* comments: "The shapes and names of things." Once forms and names were illuminated, the assignment of tasks according to ability followed—appointing tasks based on inherent capacity. Once the assignment of tasks was illuminated, investigation and reflection followed—examining with forgiveness and introspection. Once investigation and reflection were illuminated, right and wrong followed—for only when investigation and reflection are clear can right and wrong be determined. Once right and wrong were illuminated, rewards and punishments followed. *Guo* comments: "The Dao of perfect governance begins with Heaven and reaches its ultimate expression here." Once rewards and punishments were illuminated, the foolish and the wise found their proper places—each was situated appropriately. The noble and the base occupied their stations, each at ease in their position. The benevolent and worthy, the unworthy and flawed, followed their true natures. *Xi* means "to follow"; *qing* means "reality." Each followed their true reality. Abilities were necessarily distinguished, *fen* means "to differentiate," and names were necessarily adhered to. *Xuan* comments: "Demanding reality in accordance with names." With these, one served superiors, nurtured inferiors, governed things, and cultivated the self. Knowledge and scheming were not employed, and one necessarily returned to one’s Heaven-given nature. *Xuan* comments: "Returning to emptiness, stillness, and non-action." This is called Great Peace, the ultimate in governance.

Thus, the ancient records state: "There are forms and names." Forms and names were known to the ancients, yet they were not what came first. Those of old who spoke of the Great Dao underwent five transformations before forms and names could be discussed, and nine transformations before rewards and punishments could be spoken of. From "illuminating Heaven" to "forms and names," there were five transformations in discourse; from there to "rewards and punishments," there were nine transformations. To abruptly speak of forms and names is to be ignorant of the root; to abruptly speak of rewards and punishments is to be ignorant of the beginning. To speak in a way that inverts the Dao, or to expound in a manner contrary to the Dao—*Shiwen* notes: "*Wu* is pronounced as *wu*; Sima comments: 'Transverse.'"—this refers to speech that does not follow the proper sequence. Such is what people govern, but it is only fit for petty men who are governed. How could it govern others!

To abruptly speak of forms and names, rewards and punishments—this is to possess the tools of governance but not the Dao of governance. It may be employed in the world, but it is insufficient to employ the world. This is called a sophist, a person of partial understanding. *Cheng* comments: "This refers to those who merely adorn themselves with flowery words, partial and obstinate people of one corner."

Rituals, laws, measures, and standards; forms, names, comparisons, and investigations—these were known to the ancients. They are what inferiors use to serve superiors, not what superiors use to nurture inferiors. What superiors value lies in nourishing the people.

In ancient times, Shun asked Yao: "How does the Son of Heaven apply his heart?" Yao replied: "I do not treat the helpless with arrogance, nor do I dismiss the stubborn people as beyond teaching. I do not abandon the destitute, *Cheng* comments: "Relieving the poor, this heart does not falter." nor do I grieve for the dead. I delight in young children and pity women. *Ku* means "to sorrow for"; *jia* means "to cherish." *Ruzi* are young children. *Ai* means "to pity." This is how I apply my heart." Shun said: "It is fine, yet it is not great." Yao asked: "Then what should it be?" Shun replied: "One embodies the virtue of Heaven and emerges in tranquility. *Guo* comments: "Harmonizing with Heaven’s virtue, one remains still even while active." The sun and moon shine, and the four seasons proceed, as regular as the alternation of day and night. Clouds drift and rain falls." *Guo* comments: "All these occur naturally, without contrivance." *Cheng* comments: "*Jing* means 'constant.'" Yao said: "How entangled and troubled I am! *Jiaojiao* means "fixed and unyielding"; *raorao* means "confused and restless." Moved by Shun’s words, he realized his own busyness. You are in accord with Heaven; I am in accord with man." *Cheng* comments: "Your virtue is far-reaching, in accord with Heaven; my heart is close, aligned with human affairs."

Heaven and Earth are what the ancients held to be greatest, and what the Yellow Emperor, Yao, and Shun all praised in common. Thus, those of old who ruled all under Heaven—what did they do? They simply embodied Heaven and Earth.

Confucius deposited his writings in the Zhou archives. *Sima* comments: "He stored his written works there."

Yao comments: "This is also Han-era speech. 'Depositing writings' refers to the sage foreseeing the Qin burning of books and concealing them in advance—what is called 'hiding them in famous mountains.'" Zilu counseled, saying: "I have heard that in Zhou there was an Archivist Sima." *Sima* comments: "Zhengcang was the name of the archive. Another opinion: *zheng* means 'canonical.'" "The archivist was the scribe of the repository. There was one named Lao Dan who resigned and returned home." *Shiwen* notes: "Seeing that the end of Zhou could no longer be rectified, he departed." "If you wish to deposit your writings, Master, you might try going to him for assistance." Thus he suggested entrusting the writings to him. Confucius said: "Very well." He went to see Lao Dan, but Lao Dan refused—he would not permit the deposit. Thereupon Confucius expounded the Twelve Classics to persuade him. *Shiwen* notes: "One opinion: the Six Classics—*Odes*, *Documents*, *Changes*, *Rites*, *Music*, and *Spring and Autumn*—together with the Six Weft Texts, make the Twelve Classics. Another opinion: the *Changes*’ upper and lower canons with the Ten Wings make twelve. Yet another: the *Spring and Autumn*’s twelve ducal reigns." Lao Dan was struck by his discourse. *Shiwen* notes: "*Zhong* (struck), read as *dingzhong*." *Cheng* comments: "He acknowledged its reasonableness." *Xuan* comments: "His words were not yet complete." Note: Below it says "too deceitful"—this shows he did not assent. *Cheng*’s explanation is unclear. That he was "struck by his discourse" must mean he had heard only half, hence the following "indeed." He said: "Too deceitful!" *Cheng* comments: "He found it overly verbose and deceitful." *Xuan* comments: "*Man* means 'to deceive,' pronounced *man* in the level tone." Note: Verbosity verges on deceit; he feared much of it was empty words. "I would hear its essentials." Confucius said: "The essentials lie in benevolence and righteousness." Lao Dan said: "May I ask: are benevolence and righteousness part of human nature?" Confucius said: "Indeed. Without benevolence, the noble man cannot be perfected; without righteousness, he cannot live." *Perfected* and *live* are both spoken in terms of the Dao. "Benevolence and righteousness are the true nature of the perfected person—what more could be done?" What else would one do? Lao Dan said: "May I ask what benevolence and righteousness are?" Confucius said: "Joy shared with all things at heart, universal love without partiality—this is the true nature of benevolence and righteousness." *True nature* means reality. Lao Dan said: "Alas! How nearly this resembles later words!" It resembles the speech of later pedantic Confucians. "Is not 'universal love' impractical? 'Without partiality' is itself partial." *Su Yu* comments: "Not forgetting the intent to be impartial is itself partial—this echoes Zhuangzi’s reply to the Grand Steward Dang in the following chapter." "Do you wish, Master, to ensure that the world loses none of its shepherds?" *Sima* comments: "*Mu* means 'to nurture.'" "For Heaven and Earth already have their constancy, sun and moon already have their brightness, stars and constellations already have their ranks, birds and beasts already have their flocks, trees and plants already have their standing. You, Master, need only follow virtue in your conduct and pursue the Dao in your course—you have already arrived. Why then do you so strenuously brandish benevolence and righteousness, as if beating a drum to seek a lost child?" *Shiwen* notes: "*Jiejie* means 'with effort.'" *Cheng* comments: "A lost child is a runaway." Note: *Jie* means "to raise." This passage also appears in the "Revolutions of Heaven" chapter. "Alas!"

"Master, you confuse human nature!"

Guo comments: "To love when matters arise and to stop when righteousness demands—this is to forget benevolence and righteousness. Yet to dwell upon them constantly is to disorder the true."

Xuan notes: "What the Master calls 'aligning with righteousness' and what Mencius terms 'acting through benevolence and righteousness' both express this idea."

Shi Chengqi, having met Laozi, asked: "I have heard that you, Master, are a sage. I did not hesitate to journey from afar, desiring to see you. For a hundred lodgings, my feet bore calluses without rest."

Sima comments: "A hundred lodgings means a hundred nights of stopping."

The *Huainanzi*’s *Xiumu Xun* offers a high note: "*Jian* refers to calluses formed on the feet."

"Now I observe you, Master, and you are no sage. There is surplus grain in the rat’s burrow, yet you discard what your younger sister has left—this is not benevolent."

Guo comments: "This speaks of his disregard for things."

Cheng explains: "*Shurang* refers to soil from a rat’s burrow. *Mei* means 'obscure.'"

Note: Chengqi speaks of what he has witnessed. The surplus grain could be kept, yet he allows it to scatter carelessly across the ground, feigning ignorance—this is not benevolent. Raw and cooked provisions are not exhausted before him,

Cheng explains: "Raw refers to grains and silks; cooked refers to food and drink—all in abundance."

yet he amasses without limit."

He hoards without end.

Laozi remained silent and unresponsive.

Cheng comments: "He deemed it unworthy of reply."

The next day, Shi Chengqi returned and said: "Yesterday, I reproached you, but now my heart has corrected and retreated. My heart is righted and has withdrawn—no longer holding the same base views. Why is this?"

Laozi replied: "Those who are cunning, wise, divine, or sagely—I have long considered myself free of such names. When you called me an ox, I answered as an ox; when you called me a horse, I answered as a horse. If one possesses the reality, yet refuses the name others give, one invites double misfortune. To have the reality but reject its name is to incur repeated harm. My bearing is constant,

Guo comments: "Bearing refers to one’s demeanor and conduct. Not being swayed by praise or blame, one’s countenance remains unchanged."

yet I do not bear it as if it were a burden."

Guo comments: "To act with intention is to be unable to maintain constancy."

Shi Chengqi stepped aside like a goose avoiding its shadow,

Xuan notes: "A description of shifting to the side."

walking in his shoes,

Su Yu comments: "In ancient times, one removed shoes before entering a room and walked on mats. *Lüxing* (walking in shoes) suggests a loss of propriety. When Zhuangzi, his shoes tied with hemp, met the King of Wei, the king marveled at his exhaustion due to his worn shoes being bound with hemp and thus unable to be removed."

and then advanced to ask: "How should one cultivate the self?"

Laozi said: "Your countenance is haughty, standing apart as if superior. Your gaze is piercing, staring straight ahead. Your forehead is prominent,

The *Shiwen* notes: "*Kui* is pronounced *qugui*; some texts write it as *xian*."

Cheng comments: "A high and lofty forehead, adorned with ostentatious embellishment."

your mouth gapes open,

Guo comments: "A wide and open appearance."

and your demeanor is imposing."

*Yi* is read as *e*; see the *Da Zongshi* chapter.

"You are like a horse tethered in place,

Xuan notes: "Your ambition is to gallop."

restrained in movement,

Xuan notes: "You wish to move but forcibly restrain yourself."

quick as a crossbow’s trigger,

Xuan notes: "Your actions are swift as a mechanism."

scrutinizing and meticulous. Your cleverness and skill are displayed in arrogance. All this is deemed untrustworthy."

Guo Songtao comments: "All these traits are discordant with natural nature—this is what is called untrustworthy."

"There are such people at the borders—they are called thieves."

Sima comments: "This refers to such people existing in distant lands."

*Qie* means "thief."

The Master said—

Cheng comments: "Zhuangzi refers to Laozi as Master."

"The Dao, in its greatness, is not exhaustive; in its smallness, it is not neglected.

Xuan notes: "It encompasses the boundless and enters the infinitesimal."

Thus, the ten thousand things are complete. Vast and expansive, it admits all without exception—

*Guangguang* is akin to *kuangkuang*; see the *Han Shu*’s *Wu Wuzhi Zhuan*.

deep and unfathomable. Form, virtue, benevolence, and righteousness are but the vestiges of spirit—

Cheng comments: "The traces left by spirit."

who but the perfected person can stabilize them?

Xuan notes: "Few in the world are not deluded by superficial learning."

The perfected person possesses the world—is this not grand? Yet it is not enough to burden them. The world may strive, but they do not join in its pursuits.

The *Shuowen* notes: "*Bing* may also be written with the radical *shou*."

The world contends for power and influence, yet they alone do not partake. They are certain of what requires no borrowing and do not shift with profit. They embrace reality and do not alter for harmony. They exhaust the truth of things and preserve their essence—

Cheng comments: "They fully grasp principle and exhaust nature, moving without disturbing stillness."

thus, they leave Heaven and Earth behind, forsake the ten thousand things, and their spirit is never constrained. They penetrate the Dao, align with virtue, set aside benevolence and righteousness, and cast off ritual and music—

Yu comments: "*Bin* is read as *bin*, meaning to reject. It refers to casting aside ritual and music. The characters *bin* and *bin* were interchangeable in ancient times."

the heart of the perfected person is settled."

The world values the Dao in written form, yet writing is but speech, and speech holds value. What is valued in speech is meaning, and meaning follows something beyond. What meaning follows cannot be transmitted through words, yet the world prizes the transmission of words through writing. Though the world values this, I still do not deem it worthy—for what it values is not true value.

Guo comments: "True value always lies beyond words and speech."

Thus, what can be seen are forms and colors; what can be heard are names and sounds. Alas! The world believes forms, colors, names, and sounds suffice to grasp the essence of the Dao!

If forms, colors, names, and sounds truly do not suffice to grasp its essence—

Yan comments: "‘That’ refers to the Dao; ‘essence’ means reality."

—then those who know do not speak, and those who speak do not know. How could the world recognize this?

Duke Huan was reading in the hall while Bian, the wheelwright, was hewing a wheel below. Setting aside his hammer and chisel, Bian ascended and asked the duke, "May I dare ask what words Your Lordship is reading?"

The duke replied, "The words of the sages."

Bian asked, "Are the sages still alive?"

The duke said, "They have already died."

Bian said, "Then what Your Lordship reads is but the dregs and lees of the ancients!"

Sima comments: "Dregs rot into lees."

Another version reads *husks*.

Duke Huan said, "I am reading—how can a wheelwright presume to criticize? If you have an explanation, it may be acceptable; if not, you shall die."

Wheelwright Bian said, "Your servant observes this through his own craft. When hewing a wheel, if done too slowly, it is loose and insecure; if too swiftly, it is tight and will not fit. Neither too slow nor too fast—it is grasped by the hand and responds to the heart. The mouth cannot express it, yet a method resides within. Li comments: "Method, or technique."

I cannot make my son understand it, nor can my son receive it from me. Thus, though I am seventy years old, I still hew wheels in my old age.

Cheng comments: "This shows that each thing has its own nature, which cannot be imitated."

The ancients and what they could not transmit—

Yan comments: "The same as above."

—have perished. Then what Your Lordship reads is but the dregs and lees of the ancients!"

---

**Outer Chapters, "Heaven’s Revolutions" (Chapter Fourteen)**

Does Heaven revolve?
Guo comments: "It does not revolve yet moves of itself."

Does Earth abide?
Guo comments: "It does not abide yet rests of itself."

Do the sun and moon contend for their places?
Guo comments: "They do not contend yet alternate of themselves."

Who directs this? Who governs this?

Who sits idle yet sets these in motion?
These three lines respectively correspond to "Heaven," "Earth," and "the sun and moon."

Is it that there is some mechanism, some inevitability at work?
Cheng comments: "Mechanism refers to a hinge; inevitability, to a closure. It means there is a governing hinge and closure, and things proceed of necessity."

Or is it that they revolve and turn yet cannot stop themselves?
Does the cloud make the rain? Does the rain make the cloud?
Who causes this swelling and pouring forth?
Xuan comments: "Swelling refers to the rising of clouds; pouring forth, to the falling of rain."

Who sits idle, indulging in excess and joy, yet urges these on?
Xuan comments: "Clouds and rain arise from the harmonious intermingling of yin and yang qi, thus they are seen as the wanton delight of creation."

The wind rises in the north, now west, now east, drifting upward in restless motion—
Sima’s text reads "panghuang," explaining: "A whirlwind."
—who exhales and inhales this?
Who sits idle yet scatters and stirs this?
May I ask the reason?

Wu Xian Zhao says:
Li comments: "Wu Xian was a minister of Yin. Zhao is an appended name."
Xuan comments: "Zhao is likely a corruption of 'zhao' (to summon). It is said that Wu Xian, the minister, was summoned to answer."

"Come! I will tell you."

Heaven possesses the Six Ultimates and the Five Constants—
Sima comments: "The Six Ultimates are the four directions, above, and below."
Cheng comments: "The Five Constants refer to the Five Phases."

When emperors and kings align with them, there is order; when they oppose them, there is calamity. The affairs of the Ninefold Luo bring governance to completion and virtue to fulfillment, illuminating the lands below—
Yang Shen comments: "The Ninefold Luo refers to the Nine Categories of the Luo River Chart."

All under heaven revere them; this is called the Supreme Sovereign.

Guo Songtao comments: "The movement of Heaven is simply natural. Emperors and kings align with this naturalness, responding through the Dao."

The Grand Steward Dang of Song questioned Zhuangzi about benevolence.
Sima comments: "Song refers to the state of Song. Grand Steward is an office; Dang is his name."

Zhuangzi said: "Tigers and wolves are benevolent."
Dang asked: "What do you mean?"
Zhuangzi said: "Father and son cherish one another—how is this not benevolent?"
Dang said: "May I ask about perfect benevolence?"
Zhuangzi said: "Perfect benevolence has no partiality."
The Grand Steward said: "Dang has heard that without partiality there is no love, and without love there is no filial piety. Can one say that perfect benevolence is unfilial?"
Zhuangzi said: "Not so. Perfect benevolence is lofty indeed; filial piety is simply insufficient to describe it. Filial piety is but one aspect of benevolence. This is not a statement that surpasses filial piety, but one that does not yet reach it. As you claim, taking partial love as perfect benevolence—this is not a statement that surpasses filial piety, but one that does not yet reach it. Consider one traveling south to Ying: facing north, they cannot see Mount Ming—
Sima comments: "Mount Ming is a mountain in the Northern Sea."
—why is this? Because it lies too far away."

"The analogy of taking partial love as perfect benevolence is thus made. Therefore, it is said: to practice filial piety through reverence is easy; to practice it through love is difficult. To practice filial piety through love is easy; to forget one’s kin is difficult. To forget one’s kin is easy; to make one’s kin forget me is difficult. To make one’s kin forget me is easy; to forget all under Heaven is difficult. To forget all under Heaven is easy; to make all under Heaven forget me is difficult.

Virtue leaves Yao and Shun behind without effort—possessing the virtue of Yao and Shun yet not deliberately emulating them—this is my forgetting of all under Heaven. Benefit and grace extend to ten thousand generations, yet none in the world know it—this is the world forgetting me. How could one merely sigh and speak of benevolence and filial piety? Benevolence and filial piety are not worth mentioning.

Filial piety and brotherly love, benevolence and righteousness, loyalty and trust, integrity and incorruptibility—these are all self-exhortations that enslave one’s virtue. Such things are not worthy of praise. Therefore, it is said: the most noble casts aside state-bestowed ranks; the most wealthy casts aside state-held wealth. The deepest aspiration casts aside fame and reputation. The deepest aspiration lies in knowing one’s nature and attaining harmony, while fame and reputation are not worth speaking of. Thus, the Dao remains unchanging."

Master Beimen Cheng asked the Yellow Emperor: "When the Emperor performed the music of *Xianchi* in the wilds of Dongting, I first heard it with fear, then heard it with weariness, and finally heard it in confusion. Vast and silent, I could no longer find myself."

The Emperor said: "You were nearly right! I perform it with human affairs, verify it with Heaven, conduct it with ritual and righteousness, and establish it upon the Great Purity. The supreme music first responds to human affairs, accords with Heaven’s principle, is enacted through the Five Virtues, and answers to nature. Only then does it harmonize the four seasons and bring perfect harmony to the myriad things."

The vulgar editions have杂入 these passages." The four seasons arise in succession—"the five notes correspond to the four seasons and continue in endless succession." The myriad things follow their cycles of birth—"the various instruments imitate the myriad things and revolve in ceaseless performance." Now flourishing, now declining, their patterns of civil and martial order unfold; "Ethics (lún) refers to principle; constancy (jīng) to the unchanging.
