Summer flourishes while winter declines; spring is civil while autumn is martial—this principle of generation and destruction is the eternal Dao of Heaven." Now clear, now turbid, yin and yang harmonize, their sounds flowing with radiant brilliance; "Clear and turbid blend as the two qi unite; when they interact, their luminous splendor overflows." When hibernating creatures first stir, I startle them with thunder; its end has no tail, its beginning no head; now dying, now living, now collapsing, now rising. What is constant knows no end, yet not one moment can be awaited.

Guo Songtao says: "The rise of thunder reveals neither its origin nor its conclusion. Its origin is the head, the inception of life; its conclusion is the tail, the return of death. Death and life are the great constancies of the myriad things, infinite as Heaven itself—yet when a single moment suddenly arrives, even things cannot await it. This illustrates how music’s transformations arise from nature."

Yu Yue says: "That which 'cannot be awaited' means nothing can be awaited. The character *yī* (一) here carries the sense of 'all,' as seen in the *Lú* commentary to the *Dà Dài Lǐjì* and the *Yáng* commentary to the *Xúnzǐ*."

Guo Xiang says: "When change itself is taken as constant, what is constant becomes endless." Thus, you were afraid.

I then perform it with the harmony of yin and yang, illuminating it with the brightness of sun and moon—"what is called employing the Dao of Heaven." Its sounds can be short or long, soft or hard; their transformations are uniform yet not bound to fixed patterns. In the valley, it fills the valley; in the ravine, it fills the ravine—"the Dao of supreme music leaves nothing unpervaded." It seals the gaps and guards the spirit; "Gaps (xì) share the same meaning as 'crevices.' It blocks the apertures of the heart’s knowledge and preserves the spirit of concentrated unity." It takes things as its measure—referring to the meaning of the two preceding lines about "filling the valley."

Its sounds surge and spread, "what is called unfolding harmony." "Surge (huī) means movement; spread (chuò) means breadth. Like thunder’s reverberation, its sounds are vast and expansive." Its name is lofty and luminous. "Lofty as the heavens above, luminous as the sun and moon; since its sounds are so vast, its name is equally lofty and bright."

Thus, ghosts and spirits guard their hidden realms—"each attains its proper place without interference, as the *Laozi* says: 'When the Dao benefits the world, its ghosts do not haunt.'" The sun, moon, and stars follow their celestial paths—"without deviating from their courses."

I halt it at what is bounded, yet let it flow into what knows no end. Su Yu says: "What is bounded, I join in its boundedness; what knows no end, I join in its endlessness—halting and flowing both follow nature."

I wish to ponder it but cannot comprehend it, to gaze upon it but cannot see it, to pursue it but cannot reach it. Helplessly, I stand upon the Way of the fourfold emptiness—"helplessly (tǎngrán) describes a state without intention, standing upon the vast emptiness of the four directions." Leaning against a withered catalpa, I chant. (See the *Qiwù Lùn*.)

The eyes exhaust their vision in what they wish to see, strength falters in what it seeks to pursue—I can no longer keep up! The body fills the void, even to the point of meandering. You meander, thus you grow weary. Su Yu says: "You follow the music’s meandering and thus grow weary."

I then perform it with sounds that know no weariness, tuning it to the mandate of nature—"for all living things, nature is their inherent mandate. When this music is performed, it harmonizes the heart of creation and aligns with the natural mandate of all things."

Thus, it seems to mingle and chase in tangled profusion, like a forest of music without form; Guo Songtao says: "The *Shuōwén* defines a cluster of trees as a *lín* (forest). *Línlè* means rejoicing together. The five notes converge in abundance, their origins indistinguishable—hence, 'without form.'" It spreads and scatters without trailing—dispersing and flourishing as if trailing yet never trailing. It dwells in darkness without sound—"its sounds are subtle."

The movement knows no fixed direction, dwelling in profound obscurity;
Some call it death, some call it life;
Some call it substance, some call it flourishing.
Flowing, scattering, shifting—no sound remains constant.
Guo says: "It transforms with things."

The world doubts this, examining it through the sage.
*Ji* means to investigate.
By observing the sage, one knows the wonder of supreme music—no need for doubt.
The sage is one who penetrates the essence of things and fulfills their mandate.
Cheng says: "One who comprehends the essence of all things and follows the mandate of nature is called a sage."

The celestial mechanism is not displayed, yet the five organs are all complete—this is called celestial music.
Guo says: "Joy is sufficient without display; completeness does not depend on tension."
Without words, the heart is delighted.
Guo says: "Delight lies in harmony, not in speech."

Thus, the Yan Clan composed a hymn for it.
Cheng says: "Yan refers to Shennong."
*Shiwen* notes: "*Yan* is also written as *Yan*."
*"Hear it yet perceive no sound,
See it yet discern no form,
Filling heaven and earth,
Enveloping the six directions."*
Such is the hymn to this music.

You wish to hear it yet find no means to grasp it—hence your confusion.
*Er* here refers to you.
Music begins with awe; awe gives rise to dread.
Before great harmony is achieved, listening induces terror, as if facing misfortune.

I then follow it with weariness; weariness leads to withdrawal.
Its sounds fade into nothingness, as if unwilling to be heard, inducing lassitude.
Finally, it ends in confusion; confusion gives rise to foolishness.
Foolishness accords with the Dao.
Cheng says: "With a mind free of distinctions, akin to darkness and delusion, vast and silent—this resembles the true Dao."

The Dao can be carried and journeyed with.
Su Yu says: "Taking the undifferentiated as the Dao, weariness nearly approaches foolishness, and thus the Dao may be encountered.
This chapter’s emphasis lies here."

Confucius journeyed west to Wei.
Cheng comments: "From Lu to Wei, hence called a westward journey."
Yan Yuan asked Master Jin,
Li notes: "Master was the Grand Music Master of Lu; Jin was his name,"
saying: "What of your Master’s conduct?"
Master Jin replied: "Alas! Your Master is doomed to distress!"
Yan Yuan asked: "Why?"
Master Jin said: "Before the straw dogs are set out,
Li notes: "Straw bound into dogs, used by shamans and invocators,"
they are stored in fine baskets,
Li notes: "Yan, a bamboo container,"
wrapped in embroidered cloth, and the corpse-invokers fast and purify themselves to present them.
Yet once they have been set out, passersby tread upon their heads and spines, and gatherers take them to burn as fuel.
Li notes: "Su refers to straw; gatherers use them for cooking."
Should one then take them again, store them in fine baskets, wrap them in embroidered cloth, and wander or rest beneath them, they would not escape nightmares—indeed, they would suffer repeated afflictions.
*Shiwen* notes: "*Zilin* says: 'Mi occurs when something enters the eye as an ailment.' Sima says: 'It means oppression.'"
Cheng comments: "Even if one avoids evil dreams, one would surely suffer repeated demonic torments."

Now your Master has taken the straw dogs of the former kings, already set out,
Yu notes: "This *qu* should be read as *ju* ('gather'), as in the *Shiwen* of the *Yi*'s *Cui* hexagram and the Han Shu’s *Wu Xing Zhi* commentary by Yan."
and gathers disciples to wander and rest beneath them.
Thus he was felled like a tree in Song, his traces erased in Wei, and found himself in dire straits in Shang and Zhou—was this not his nightmare?
Besieged between Chen and Cai, seven days without cooked food, life and death as close as neighbors—was this not his affliction?

In water, nothing surpasses the use of a boat; on land, nothing surpasses the use of a cart.
Yet to take a boat’s suitability for water and seek to push it on land is to spend a lifetime moving no more than a few paces.
Eight feet make a *xun*; twice a *xun* makes a *chang*.
Are past and present not like water and land?
Are Zhou and Lu not like boat and cart?
Now to seek to enact Zhou’s ways in Lu is like pushing a boat on land—laborious yet fruitless, and one’s person will surely meet with calamity.
He does not understand the transmission without fixed direction,
Sima notes: "Fang means constancy."
Guo Qingfan notes: "The *Lüshi Chunqiu*’s *Bi Ji* chapter glosses *zhuan* as 'turning.' It refers to turning without fixed direction."
the response to things that knows no exhaustion.

Have you alone not seen the well-sweep?
Pull it, and it bows; release it, and it rises.
It is pulled by people, not the one pulling people—thus it bows and rises without offending anyone.
Therefore, the rites, righteousness, laws, and standards of the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors were not prized for their sameness but for their efficacy in governance.
Cheng comments: "Jin means to esteem. Rituals and music, dignity and deportment, were not handed down unchanged."
Guo comments: "They aimed to suit the times and respond to the needs of governance."
Thus the rites, righteousness, laws, and standards of the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors are like the crabapple, pear, orange, and pomelo—
their flavors differ, yet all are pleasing to the mouth.
Thus rites, righteousness, laws, and standards are things that change with the times.

Now take a gibbon and dress it in the robes of the Duke of Zhou—it will surely bite, gnaw, tear, and rend them, casting them off entirely before it is satisfied.
*Shiwen* notes: "Li says: 'Qian means satisfaction. Originally also written as *xian*.'"
To observe the differences between past and present is like the difference between a gibbon and the Duke of Zhou.
Thus when Xishi clutched her heart in distress and knit her brows in her village,
her village’s ugly woman saw and admired it, returning home to clutch her heart and knit her brows in the same way.
The rich of her village, seeing this, firmly shut their doors and refused to go out;
the poor, seeing this, took their wives and children and fled.
She knew the beauty of knitting her brows but did not know *why* it was beautiful.
Alas! Your Master is doomed to distress!"

When Confucius reached the age of fifty-one without having heard the Dao, he journeyed south to Pei and sought out Lao Dan.
Sima [Biao] notes: "Laozi was a man of the district of Xiang in the state of Chen. Xiang is now part of Ku County and lies near Pei."
Lao Dan said: "Have you come, sir? I have heard that you are a worthy man of the north—have you also attained the Dao?"
Confucius replied: "I have not."
Laozi asked: "How then have you sought it?"
Confucius said: "I sought it in measures and numbers, but after five years I had not attained it."
Laozi asked: "How else have you sought it?"
Confucius said: "I sought it in the principles of yin and yang, but after twelve years I had not attained it."
Laozi said: "Just so. If the Dao could be offered, none would fail to offer it to their lord; if the Dao could be presented, none would fail to present it to their kin; if the Dao could be told to others, none would fail to tell it to their brothers; if the Dao could be given to others, none would fail to give it to their descendants. Yet this cannot be—there is no other reason: inwardly, without a master, it does not abide; if the center lacks a master, the Dao passes through without lingering. Outwardly, without a match, it does not proceed.
Yu [Yue] notes: "'Match' is a scribal error for *pi*. The *Ziyi* of the *Li Ji* states, 'Only the noble man can love his *pi*,' and Zheng Xuan’s commentary explains: '*Pi* should be *match*, a scribal error.' This is one such example. These two lines resemble the *Gongyang Zhuan* on Duke Xuan’s third year: 'That which issues from within has no match and does not proceed; that which comes from without has no master and does not abide.' Since that which comes from without has no master and does not abide, this text says, 'Inwardly, without a master, it does not abide.' Since that which issues from within has no match and does not proceed, this text says, 'Outwardly, without a match, it does not proceed.' Thus, in the 'Yang' chapter, 'That which enters from without has a master and is not grasped; that which issues from within has a match and is not resisted'—here, too, *match* should be *pi*, an error like the one above."
That which issues from within is not received from without—
Xuan [Ying] notes: "It is not fitting for the times, and so is not received."
—the sage does not put it forth.
Xuan notes: "He does not impose it upon others."
That which enters from without has no master within—
Xuan notes: "It is not the essence of my heart, and so has no master."
—the sage does not conceal it. Not hiding it in the heart, one must inwardly attain the essence of one’s heart and outwardly accord with the changes and transformations of the times, so that inner and outer converge as one, essence and function unite, and the sage thereby harmonizes with the Dao.
A name is a public instrument—one cannot take too much of it. Benevolence and righteousness are the travelers’ lodges of the former kings—
Sima [Biao] notes: "A travelers’ lodge is like a waystation."
—one may stay for a single night but cannot dwell there long, for frequent encounters bring many reproaches.
Xuan notes: "The more one meets, the more one is blamed."
The perfected men of old borrowed the Way from benevolence and lodged in righteousness, roaming the emptiness of carefree wandering, feeding in the fields of simplicity, standing in the gardens of non-bestowal.
Cheng [Xuanying] notes: "*Gou* means 'temporary,' *jian* means 'simple.' *Dai* means 'to bestow.' Knowing contentment, they fed in the fields of simplicity; not depleting their own resources, they stood in the gardens of non-bestowal. The text speaks of fields and gardens to show that these are the places where the sage nurtures life."
Carefree wandering is non-action; simplicity is ease of nurture; non-bestowal is no expenditure.
Xuan notes: "No cost."
The ancients called this the wandering of gathering the true.
Xuan notes: "Not enslaved by appearances."
Those who take wealth as right cannot yield emolument; those who take prominence as right cannot yield fame; those who cling to power cannot share authority. Grasping it, they tremble—
Cheng notes: "Fearful of losing it, they tremble."
—releasing it, they grieve.
Xuan notes: "They cling to it greedily."
Yet they see nothing in it to reflect upon—
Xuan notes: "They see nothing of principle in it."
—only gazing at what they cannot cease pursuing.
Xuan notes: "They are only clear about the relentless pursuit of things."
These are the people Heaven punishes.
Cheng notes: "Though no punishment from Chu has been applied, their nature is already exhausted."
Resentment, kindness, taking, giving, remonstrance, instruction, life, and death—these eight are the instruments of rectification.
Xuan notes: "The tools for setting others right."
Only those who follow great change without obstruction can employ them. Thus it is said: "To rectify is to set right."
Xuan notes: "To set right what ought to be set right."
If one’s heart does not accept this, the gate of Heaven will not open."
Cheng notes: "If one’s heart cannot be thus, the gate of the celestial mechanism is blocked and will not open. The gate of Heaven is the heart."

Confucius visited Lao Dan and spoke of benevolence and righteousness. Lao Dan said: "When chaff gets in the eye, heaven and earth and the four directions seem to shift places; when mosquitoes and gnats bite the skin, one cannot sleep through the night.
Sima [Biao] notes: "*Zan* means 'to bite.'"
Guo [Xiang] notes: "Though the external thing that afflicts one is small, the harm to one’s nature is great."
Note: *Xi* means 'night.' *Xi* and *xi* are interchangeable.
Benevolence and righteousness are so harsh that they agitate the heart—there is no greater disorder.
Xuan notes: "They disturb the heart more severely than chaff in the eye or bites on the skin."
You, sir, would have the world not lose its uncarved simplicity—
Guo notes: "Preserve wholeness and let benevolence and righteousness manifest."
—you, too, should move with the wind and act, gathering virtue and standing firm."
Xuan notes: "Return to the natural."

And why should one act so strenuously, as if carrying a war drum in search of a lost child?
Cheng notes: "*Jieran* describes the appearance of exertion."
Note: The *Tiandao* chapter quotes Laozi’s words, also saying, "Why so zealously brandish benevolence and righteousness, like striking a drum to seek a lost child?"—its meaning is the same, referring to startling the world.

The swan does not bathe daily to become white, nor the crow dye itself daily to become black.
Xuan notes: "This illustrates how their original nature is thus by itself."
The simplicity of black and white is not sufficient to serve as distinction;
Xuan notes: "What arises from original nature cannot be judged as beauty or ugliness."
The spectacle of fame and reputation is not sufficient to enlarge one’s being.
The beauty of fame and reputation cannot add to or broaden one’s inherent nature.

When the spring dries up, fish find themselves together on dry land, moistening one another with breath, wetting one another with foam—yet how much better to forget one another in the rivers and lakes!
Xuan notes: "This illustrates how petty kindnesses are inferior to forgetting one another in primal chaos, where each finds joy in what is naturally so. Why then cling to the paltriness of benevolence and righteousness?"
Note: These four lines also appear in the *Da Zongshi* chapter.

Confucius, having visited Lao Dan, returned and did not speak for three days.
Xuan notes: "He was ill at ease."
His disciples asked, "Master, when you saw Lao Dan, what correction did you receive?"
Xuan notes: "What correction did you seek to apply?"
Confucius said, "Only now have I truly seen the dragon! The dragon coalesces into form, scatters into pattern, rides the clouds and qi, and nourishes itself in yin and yang. My mouth gaped open and could not close—how could I presume to instruct Lao Dan?"

Zigong said, "Then does man indeed possess the power to dwell like a corpse yet manifest the dragon’s presence, to thunder like thunder yet remain as silent as the abyss?"—these two lines also appear in the *Zai You* chapter. "Can one act with the dynamism of heaven and earth? May I, too, observe this?"
He then announced Confucius’s arrival to Lao Dan.
Xuan notes: "He introduced Confucius in advance."
Lao Dan, lounging in the hall, responded in a faint voice:
"As the years pass and my life advances—
*Yun* means 'to move,' *wang* means 'to progress.' My advancing years have already passed.
—what admonition do you bring me?"

Zigong said, "The Three Kings and Five Emperors governed the world differently, yet their attachment to fame and reputation was the same. Why, Master, do you alone consider them not sages?"
Cheng notes: "He means Laozi rejects the Three Kings as sages."
*Shiwen* notes: "Some versions read 'Three August Ones.' According to the commentary, 'Three Kings' is correct; the rest read 'Three August Ones.'"

Lao Dan said, "Young man, come closer! Why do you say they were different?"
He replied, "..."

Yao passed the throne to Shun, Shun to Yu; Yu relied on strength while Tang used arms. King Wen submitted to Zhou without daring to oppose him, and King Wu opposed Zhou without willing to submit—thus they were not the same."

Lao Dan said, "Young man, come closer! Let me tell you of the Three August Ones and Five Emperors’ governance of the world. In the Yellow Emperor’s governance of the world, he made the people’s hearts unified: when their kin died, they did not weep, yet none among the people condemned it."
Cheng notes: "The Three August Ones practiced the Dao, and the people’s hearts were pure and unified. They did not favor only their own kin or cherish only their own children, so when kin died, they did not weep, and the world did not condemn it. If one insisted on condemning it, those who forced themselves to weep would be many."

"In Yao’s governance of the world, he made the people’s hearts affectionate: when they killed for their kin, none among the people condemned it."
Xuan notes: "Desiring to honor their kin, they demoted all others, and thus their awareness gradually opened."

"In Shun’s governance of the world, he made the people’s hearts competitive: pregnant women gave birth in ten months, and children could speak at five months."
Cheng notes: "In ancient times, pregnant women carried their children for fourteen months before giving birth, and children only began to speak at two years old. Now, this differs from antiquity."
"Before they could even babble, they began to distinguish others."
Cheng notes: "Before they could laugh as infants, they already discerned right and wrong."
Guo notes: "*Shui* means to differentiate people."
Xuan notes: "The primordial *qi* withered early."

"In Yu’s governance of the world, he made the people’s hearts mutable: people had intentions, and warfare had its justifications."
Xuan notes: "People had cunning, and killing was seen as aligning with Heaven and following the people."
"Killing thieves was not considered killing."
Xuan notes: "They deemed it natural."
"People formed their own factions, and thus the world took shape—each forming their own cliques to constitute the world. As a result, the world was greatly alarmed, and the Confucians and Mohists all arose."
Cheng notes: "Alarming the world led the Confucians to exalt Yao and Shun to conceal their faults, and the Mohists to follow Yu’s way and justify themselves."

"Their beginnings had order, but now even women—though their beginnings still had principles, what is practiced today is that men adopt the ways of women. What can be said of this! Let me tell you: the governance of the Three August Ones and Five Emperors was called ‘governance,’ yet no disorder was greater. The wisdom of the Three Kings—here, *Three August Ones* should read *Three Kings*, otherwise it is incoherent—obstructed the brightness of the sun and moon above, severed the essence of mountains and rivers below, and disrupted the operations of the four seasons in between. Their knowledge was more cruel than the sting of a scorpion’s tail."
Wang Yinzhi notes: "*Chai* and *chan* are both alternative names for scorpions. The *Guangya* states, ‘*Chai* and *chan* are scorpions’ (the present edition omits the character *chan*; the *Zhongjing yinyi* vol. 5 cites ‘*chai* are scorpions,’ and the *Jiyun* cites ‘*chan* are *chai*’). Pronounced *luda*. *Chai* and *chan* are names for venomous stings that harm people. *Chai* is cognate with *zhe* (pronounced *zhe*), and *chan* with *la*. The *Guangya Shigu* states, ‘*Du*, *zhe*, and *la* mean pain,’ which is their meaning. *Zhe* and *chan* share the same ancient pronunciation. *Zhe* and *chan* are the same character. In the *Records of the Grand Historian*, the *Qin Benji* reads ‘Duke Ligong,’ while the *Shihuang Benji* writes ‘Duke Lagong.’ *La* interchangeably written as *li* is like *chan* interchangeably written as *zhe*."

"Like ravenous beasts—*xian’gui* is unclear, but likely refers to man-eating creatures—none could secure the tranquility of their nature and life, yet they still considered themselves sages. Is this not shameful? How utterly shameless!"

Gongzi was visibly unsettled, shifting uneasily where he stood. Confucius said to Lao Dan, “I have studied the Six Classics—*Shi*, *Shu*, *Li*, *Yue*, *Yi*, and *Chunqiu*—for so long that I believed I had thoroughly grasped their essence. Yet after expounding the Way of the former kings and clarifying the traces of Zhou and Shao to seventy-two rulers, not a single one took up my teachings. How profoundly difficult it is to persuade others! Is the Dao so hard to illuminate?”

Laozi replied, “Fortunate indeed that you have not encountered a ruler of a well-ordered age! The Six Classics are but the worn traces of the former kings—how could they be the very essence that produced those traces? What you speak of are still only traces. Traces are what the shoe leaves behind, but are traces the shoe itself?

When white egrets gaze at one another, their pupils remain still, yet they conceive through the wind’s influence.” Sima Biao notes, “They transform by responding to the wind’s qi.” He also says, “They gaze and thereby form yin and yang.” Xuan comments, “‘Remain still’ means fixing their eyes unwaveringly.” *Note: Here, ‘wind’ should be read as in ‘horses and cattle mate by the wind,’ referring to the mutual attraction of male and female.* ‘Transform’ means they are moved to conceive. Insects, too, with the male calling from the upwind and the female responding from downwind, conceive through the wind’s influence.” Xuan comments, “They transmit sound and thereby conceive.” Some creatures are self-sufficient as both male and female, thus they conceive through the wind’s influence. The *Shiwen* cites the *Shanhaijing*: “On Danyuan Mountain there is a beast resembling a leopard but with fur, called the *shilei*; on Dai Mountain there is a bird resembling a phoenix with five-colored markings, called the *qilei*—both are self-sufficient as male and female.”

Nature cannot be altered, fate cannot be changed—these are the unchanging truths. Time cannot be halted, the Dao cannot be obstructed—these are the ceaseless transformations. If one attains the Dao, there is nothing that cannot be done; if one loses it, there is nothing that can be done.” Guo comments, “Even in transformation without fixed form, all is possible.” “Wherever it is lost, nothing is possible.”

Three months later, Confucius reappeared and said, “I have grasped it! Crows and magpies hatch their young; fish spawn in foam.” Li notes, “They conceive by coating their mouths with foam.” “Slender-waisted creatures transform.” The *Liezi* *Shiwen*, citing Sima, explains, “The young of slender-waisted wasps take silkworm grubs, enchant them, and raise them as their own offspring.” “A younger brother weeps when his elder brother is born, fearing the loss of his parents’ love.” This illustrates the vast differences in the natures of things.

For so long, I have not been one with the transformations of humanity! If I cannot be one with the workings of creation, how can I transform others?”

Laozi said, “Good. You have grasped it.”

**Chapter Fifteen: Carving Intentions (Outer Chapters)**

Those who carve their intentions and exalt their conduct do so with severe rigor; their conduct is lofty and noble. They distance themselves from the world and reject its customs, voicing lofty critiques and resentful reproaches. Li notes, “They condemn the world’s lack of the Dao and resent their own failure to meet with it.” This is but defiance for its own sake—such are the scholars of mountain valleys, those who hold the world in contempt. Xuan comments, “‘Hold in contempt’ means to regard it lightly.” These are the pursuits of those who wither away or plunge into the abyss. Sima notes, “Those who wither away, like Bao Jiao or Jie Zitui; those who plunge into the abyss, like Shentu Di.”

Those who speak of benevolence, righteousness, loyalty, and trust, who practice reverence, frugality, and deference, do so merely to cultivate themselves. These are the scholars of orderly times, the teachers of humanity. Cheng comments, “These are the scholars of peaceful eras, those who instruct others—like Confucius dwelling by the Zhu and Si rivers, or Zixia in the West River.” These are the pursuits of wandering scholars and recluses.

Those who discourse on great achievements, establish grand reputations, uphold the rites between ruler and minister, and rectify the hierarchy of superior and inferior do so merely to govern. These are the scholars of the court, those who honor their lord and strengthen the state—pursuits favored by those who seek to annex rival states.

Those who retreat to marshes and lakes, dwell in seclusion and open spaces, fish and idle in tranquility, do so merely to practice nonaction. Xuan comments, “Nonaction here means idleness and leisure.” These are the scholars of rivers and seas, those who flee the world—pursuits favored by the carefree.

Those who practice exhaling and inhaling, expelling the old and drawing in the new, emulate the bear’s climbing and the bird’s stretching. Cheng comments, “They exhale the cold and expel the old, inhale the warm and draw in the new—like a bear climbing a tree and hanging from it, or a bird soaring in the sky and stretching its limbs.” They do so merely to prolong life. These are the practitioners of *daoyin*, those who nurture the body. Such are the pursuits of those who, like Pengzu, seek long life.

Yet there are those who attain loftiness without carving their intentions, cultivate themselves without benevolence or righteousness, govern without achievements or fame, find leisure without retreating to rivers or seas, and prolong life without *daoyin*. They forget nothing, yet possess all things. Guo comments, “By forgetting, they are able to possess.” Serene and boundless, all beauties follow them. Xuan comments, “Without fixing on any single extreme, all excellences are complete.” This is the Dao of Heaven and Earth, the virtue of the sage.

Thus it is said: Tranquility and indifference are one and the same—stillness, emptiness, nonbeing, and nonaction. This is the equilibrium of Heaven and Earth and the essence of the Dao and virtue. The *Shiwen* notes, “Essence here means correctness.” Xuan comments, “The root.”

Thus it is said: The sage rests, and in resting attains equilibrium and ease. *Shiwen* notes, “Rest means repose.” Yu comments, “The original text reads, ‘Thus it is said: The sage rests, and in resting attains equilibrium and ease.’ The transposition of ‘rests’ and ‘thus’ is a later scribal error. The *Tian Dao* chapter states, ‘Thus the Thearch, the king, and the sage rest, and in resting attain emptiness,’ which follows a similar structure and may be used to correct this passage.” Guo’s commentary, Cheng’s subcommentary, and Lu’s annotations all preserve only the single word “rest,” and Yu’s emendation is correct—this is an error in later printed editions.

In equilibrium and ease, they attain tranquility and indifference. In equilibrium, ease, tranquility, and indifference, cares and afflictions cannot enter, and perverse *qi* cannot assail. Thus their virtue remains whole, and their spirit does not wane.

Thus it is said: In life, the sage moves with the Heavenly course. Guo comments, “They move in accord with spontaneity.” In death, they transform with things. Guo comments, “They shed their form without attachment.” In stillness, they share the virtue of *yin*; in motion, they share the wave of *yang*. Guo comments, “In movement and stillness, they are without intention, entrusting themselves to *yin* and *yang*.” (These four lines also appear in the *Tian Dao* chapter.)

They do not precede fortune, nor do they initiate misfortune. They respond only after being stirred, act only under compulsion, and rise only when there is no alternative. They discard knowledge and artifice—*Guanzi*’s *Xin Shu* chapter states, “Discard knowledge and artifice,” and this passage adopts its phrasing. The *Huainanzi*’s *Zhu Shu* chapter glosses, “Artifice means cunning.” They follow the principle of Heaven. Cheng comments, “Follow means to accord with.” Thus they incur no Heaven-sent disasters, no entanglements with things, no human reproach, and no ghostly blame. (These four lines also appear in the *Tian Dao* chapter. In that text, “disasters” is written as “resentments.”)

In life, they drift like a floating leaf; in death, they rest like a returning traveler. They do not scheme in thought, nor plan in advance.

Radiant yet not dazzling, trustworthy yet not demanding—*Xuan* comments, “Without intent to claim certainty.” They sleep without dreaming, wake without worry; their spirit is pure and unalloyed, their ethereal soul never wearied. (These lines also appear in the *Tian Dao* chapter.) Empty and tranquil, they accord with Heaven’s virtue.

Thus it is said: sorrow and joy are virtue’s deviations; delight and anger are the Way’s transgressions; likes and dislikes are virtue’s losses. Therefore, a heart free of sorrow and joy is virtue’s perfection—*Guo* comments, “Supreme virtue is ever at ease, so emotions find no purchase.” Unchanging unity is quiescence’s perfection—*Guo* comments, “Quiescent and unified, it cannot be altered.” Encountering nothing contrary is emptiness’s perfection—*Guo* comments, “The mind utterly open and exhausted, without the slightest resistance.” Unmingled with things is tranquility’s perfection—*Guo* comments, “Things come of themselves; the supremely tranquil harbors no impulse to engage.” *Xuan* notes: *tan* (惔) is synonymous with *dan* (淡). Meeting nothing adverse is purity’s perfection—*Guo* comments, “If tainted by turbid desires, there will be discord.”

Thus it is said: when the body labors without rest, it breaks down; when essence is expended without cease, it wearies; weariness leads to exhaustion. Water’s nature is such that, unadulterated, it is clear; undisturbed, it is level. Yet if pent up and unstirred, it cannot remain clear—*Xuan* comments, “It will turn stagnant and foul.” Such is the image of Heaven’s virtue—*Xuan* comments, “Quiescent yet daily in motion.”

Thus it is said: pure and unadulterated, quiescent and unchanging, tranquil and non-active, moving in accord with Heaven’s course—*Guo* comments, “To move in pursuit of desire is the way of man.” This is the way to nurture the spirit.

Now, one who possesses a sword from Gan or Yue—*Sima* comments, “Gan is Wu. Wu and Yue produce fine swords.”—keeps it cased and hidden, never daring to wield it: this is the height of treasuring. When the spirit flows unimpeded in all four directions, reaching everywhere without limit, ascending to touch Heaven, descending to coil upon the earth, transforming and nourishing the myriad things yet leaving no trace—*Xuan* comments, “No image can be made of it.”—its name is “co-equal with the Emperor.” *Xuan* comments, “It shares the function of Heaven’s Emperor.”

The Way of undyed silk is to guard only the spirit, holding fast without loss, merging with the spirit as one. The unity’s refined penetration aligns with Heaven’s order—with the principle of spontaneity. A rustic saying goes: “The multitude values profit, the incorruptible value reputation, the worthy esteem aspiration, the sage treasures essence.” Thus “undyed” means nothing is mingled with it; “pure” means its spirit remains unharmed. One who embodies purity and undyed silk is called a *zhenren*—*Cheng* comments, “To embody is to awaken and comprehend. To intimately align with the principle of purity and undyed silk is to be a true person wherever one is.” (Outer Chapter *Shan Xing*, Sixteen)

To cultivate one’s nature amid the vulgar, one seeks to restore its origin through vulgar learning—*Cui* comments, “*Shan* means to refine.” *Guo* comments, “Having refined one’s nature amid the vulgar, one then seeks to restore the root of life and nature through vulgar learning.” Note: The *Xuan* edition omits one “vulgar.” According to *Guo*’s annotation, there should be two “vulgar,” though one may be superfluous. *Su Yu* says, “One ‘vulgar’ is likely superfluous; ‘learning’ and ‘thought’ form a parallel. It means that both nature and desire have been corrupted by the vulgar, and though learning and thought are both applied, they only deepen the obscurity. *Xuan*’s punctuation, treating ‘vulgar learning’ and ‘vulgar thought’ as separate phrases, seems incorrect.”

To indulge desires amid the vulgar, one seeks to attain clarity through thought—these are called the people of obscurity and delusion. The ancients who cultivated the Way nurtured knowledge with tranquility. *Shiwen* notes, “*Zhi* is pronounced as *wisdom*.” *Xuan* comments, “Stillness gives rise to wisdom.” Wisdom arises yet does not employ wisdom—this is called nurturing tranquility with wisdom. Wisdom arises but is not relied upon, thus using wisdom to nurture one’s stillness. Wisdom and tranquility mutually nurture each other, and harmony and principle emerge from one’s nature. Wisdom and tranquility nurture each other, and virtue and the Dao naturally arise from one’s nature.

Virtue is harmony; the Dao is principle. *Xuan* comments, “Virtue and the Dao are simply harmony and compliance. *Li* means compliance.” Virtue admits all without exclusion—this is benevolence. The Dao orders all without exception—this is righteousness. Virtue and the Dao give rise to benevolence and righteousness. Righteousness is manifest, and things draw near—this is loyalty. *Xuan* comments, “This is to truly possess virtue and the Dao.” The center is pure and real, returning to genuine feeling—this is joy. *Cheng* comments, “Though one engages with the world, one constantly returns to true feeling; what is attained is harmony and ease, hence it is called joy.” Trustworthy conduct, bearing, and compliance with refinement—this is ritual. True conduct in bearing and compliance with the natural patterns of refinement constitute ritual. When ritual and music are universally applied, the world falls into chaos. *Shiwen* notes, “*Bian* is pronounced as *universal*.” *Guo* comments, “Applying what one part practices and what one mind delights in universally leads to one side gaining while all others lose.” *Yu* comments, “According to *Guo*’s annotation, it should be ‘partial’ (*pian*), hence his explanation. *Shiwen*’s pronunciation is mistaken.” Note: It originally read “partial” (*pian*), but in the Tang era, it was mistakenly written as “universal” (*bian*), so *Lu* followed the text in his pronunciation, though the meaning is incoherent. The *Xuan* edition has already corrected it to “partial.”

When others are upright and receive one’s virtue, virtue is not imposed; if imposed, things will surely lose their nature. When others are upright and receive the benefit of one’s virtue, virtue responds to virtue, without forcing one’s own virtue upon them to overwhelm them. If one forces the world and overwhelms it, seeking to correct others with oneself, then many things will lose their nature.

The ancients lived in primordial chaos, in the undifferentiated state of the world’s beginning. With their generation, they attained serenity and indifference. *Cheng* comments, “Serenity, indifference, and non-action are the Way.” At that time, yin and yang were harmonious and still, ghosts and spirits did not disturb, the four seasons kept their rhythm, the myriad things were unharmed, the multitude of beings did not die prematurely, and though humans possessed knowledge, they had no use for it—this was called perfect unity. At that time, none acted, yet all was constantly spontaneous. There was no artificiality.

When virtue declined, *Suiren* and *Fuxi* began to govern the world, thus complying but not attaining unity. *Cheng* comments, “*Suiren* transformed raw into cooked; *Fuxi* drew the eight trigrams to create writing, made knotted cords into nets, and the sprouts of cunning and deceit emerged. Desires gradually arose, complying with the hearts of the common people yet unable to merge into perfect unity.” When virtue declined further, *Shennong* and the *Yellow Emperor* began to govern the world, thus bringing stability but not compliance. *Cheng* comments, “*Shennong* waged war against *Gonggong*; the *Yellow Emperor* fought *Chiyou*. They sought to stabilize the world but could not fully comply with the multitude of beings.”

When virtue declined still further, *Tang* and *Yu* began to govern the world, initiating the flow of governance and transformation. *Xuan* comments, “Losing their source.” Simplicity dissolved and unadorned nature scattered. *Shiwen* notes, “● is also written as *jiao* (superficial).” *Cheng* comments, “*Tang* and *Yu* established the five canons to regulate the five phases, appointed officials to govern the people, but the five phases declined from then on, the people became superficial and deceitful, and the pure and unadorned nature was destroyed.” They departed from the Dao to pursue goodness, risked virtue to enact conduct—*Xian* means peril. Departing from the Dao to aspire to goodness, risking virtue to regulate conduct, are like *Wuguang* and *Shentu Di*. Then they abandoned nature and followed the heart. *Xuan* comments, “Forsaking heavenly nature, employing the human heart.” The heart and its discriminating knowledge were insufficient to stabilize the world. *Xuan* comments, “People grew increasingly cunning and deceitful.” *Yu* comments, “The *Book of Songs* says, ‘Not knowing, not perceiving.’ ‘Discriminating knowledge’ is a compound; it means only without knowing and perceiving can the world be stabilized. Other interpretations, which break after ‘knowledge,’ are incorrect.”

Then they adorned it with refinement and enriched it with breadth. Refinement destroyed substance, breadth drowned the heart, and then the people began to be confused and disordered, unable to return to their nature and feelings and restore their origin.

From this perspective, the world has lost the Dao, and the Dao has lost the world. *Xuan* comments, “Taking the non-Dao as the Dao.” The world and the Dao mutually lose each other. How can the person of the Dao arise in the world, and how can the world arise in the Dao? The Dao has no means to arise in the world, and the world has no means to arise in the Dao. Even if the sage does not dwell in the mountains and forests, his virtue is concealed. *Cheng* comments, “If the sage descends into the dust of the world and mingles with the multitude, concealing his sagely virtue so that it cannot be employed, though he dwells in the marketplace, it is no different from the mountains and forests.”

The concealment lies not in self-concealment. *Xuan* comments, “In an age where the Dao is concealed, one need not conceal oneself—one is already concealed.” The so-called recluses of antiquity did not hide their bodies to avoid being seen, nor seal their words to prevent them from being spoken, nor suppress their knowledge to keep it from being expressed. Rather, the times and fate were greatly awry. Had their times and fate aligned to flourish throughout the world, they would have returned to unity without leaving a trace, reverting to the age of perfect unity where no trace of them would be seen. Had their times and fate been utterly at odds with the world, they would have deeply rooted themselves in tranquility and awaited their moment, firmly establishing the natural root and preserving the ultimate goodness to bide their time. This is the Way of preserving one’s person.

The ancients who conducted themselves in the world did not adorn their knowledge with clever disputation. *Cheng* comments, “Those of old who entrusted themselves to their conduct did not embellish petty wisdom with empty rhetoric.” They did not exhaust the world with their knowledge. *Cheng* comments, “They did not deploy their knowledge to distress the common people.” Nor did they exhaust virtue with their knowledge. *Cheng* comments, “They knew the limits of their portion and did not burden their inherent attainment with boundless pursuits.” Solemnly abiding in their proper place, they returned to their nature—what more was there to do? The Dao is not a matter of petty conduct; virtue is not a matter of petty understanding. Petty understanding harms virtue. *Cheng* comments, “Petty understanding and trivial knowledge impair the profound and mysterious virtue.” Petty conduct harms the Dao. Thus it is said: rectify oneself and that is all. To delight in wholeness is to attain one’s aspiration. To delight in the fullness of one’s nature is to fulfill one’s aspiration.

The so-called attainment of aspiration in antiquity did not refer to carriages and caps of office. It meant that nothing could augment their joy. *Guo* comments, “To be complete within and self-sufficient.” The so-called attainment of aspiration today refers to carriages and caps of office. Yet carriages and caps of office are not one’s nature or fate; they are things that arrive by chance, like lodgers. *Cheng* comments, “‘By chance’ means arriving unexpectedly.” As lodgers, their coming cannot be warded off, nor can their going be halted. Thus, one does not indulge one’s aspirations for carriages and caps of office—*tìzhì* means to let one’s aspirations run wild—nor does one conform to custom in times of poverty and hardship, degrading one’s aspirations to follow the vulgar. Their joy is the same in both circumstances, seeing carriages and caps of office and poverty and hardship as no different. Thus, they are without sorrow. Therefore, they can dwell in honor without anxiety.

Now, when the lodger departs, there is no joy—this is common to all people today. From this perspective, though they may rejoice, they are never without distraction. Those who delight in carriages and caps of office have their aspirations scattered abroad. Thus it is said: those who lose themselves in things and forfeit their nature to custom are called “the people of inverted priorities.” *Xiang* comments, “To exchange the inner for the outer is called inversion.”

When autumn floods arrive, the hundred streams pour into the Yellow River. Li comments, "Water is born in spring and swells in autumn." The vastness of its currents—where *jing* means "unobstructed," as Sima explains, while Cui’s version reads *jing* as "direct," meaning "straight passage"—spans between its two banks and islets, where the shores blur so that oxen and horses cannot be distinguished. Cheng comments, "Viewed from afar across the water, one cannot tell ox from horse." At this, the River Lord beams with self-satisfaction, believing all the world’s beauty to be contained within himself.

Following the current eastward to the Northern Sea, he turns to face the east and sees no end to the water. Cheng notes, "The Northern Sea is present-day Laizhou." There, the River Lord at last turns his gaze, looks up in awe toward Ruo, and sighs. The *Shiwen* reads *wang* as *mang*, explaining, "Mangyang means 'to gaze upward,'" while Sima comments, "Ruo is the sea god." He says, "There is a rustic saying: 'Having heard a hundred ways of the Dao, one believes none surpasses oneself.' This describes me. Moreover, I once heard of those who belittle Confucius’s learning and dismiss Boyi’s righteousness, and I did not believe it. Now that I have witnessed the boundlessness of your realm, had I not come to your gate, I would have remained in peril. I would long have been ridiculed by the masters of the Great Dao." Sima explains, "The Great Dao is the grand principle."

The Northern Sea’s Ruo replies, "A well fish cannot be spoken to of the sea, for it is confined by its dwelling. Wang Yinzhi argues, "Originally, the text read 'well *fish*,' later altered by others. The *Taiping Yulan*’s sections on seasons, fish and shellfish, and insects all quote this passage as 'A well *fish* cannot be spoken to of the sea,' proving the original text used *fish*. Moreover, the *Shiwen* does not gloss *wa* (frog) here but only does so later under 'the frog in the shallow well,' where it notes, 'Wa, also written as [蜗], pronounced *huwo*,' and cites Sima’s comment, 'A frog is a water creature resembling a toad.' This clearly shows the original read *fish*, not *frog*. If it were *frog*, the pronunciation and annotation would appear here, not later. Furthermore, the *Huainanzi*’s 'Yuan Dao' chapter states, 'A well fish cannot be told of the great, for it is confined by narrowness,' and Liang scholar Zhang Wan writes, 'The well fish knows not the vast sea; the summer insect knows not winter ice.' The *Shui Jing Zhu*’s commentary on the Gan River says, 'I record this curious text to broaden the hearing of the well fish.' All these draw from Zhuangzi, confirming the original text read 'well *fish*.' In the *Yi Jing*’s hexagram *Jing* (Well), line nine-three reads, 'The well’s valley shoots forth minnows,' with Zheng Xuan noting, 'The fish born in wells are not large, only minnows.' (Cited in Liu Kui’s commentary on the *Wu Du Fu*.) The *Kunxue Jiwen* (Vol. 10) quotes the *Taiping Yulan*’s version of Zhuangzi: 'For those whose thoughts are like well fish, I cast my hook to lure them.' The *Lüshi Chunqiu*’s 'Yu Da' chapter states, 'There are no large fish in wells.' All these attest to 'well fish.' Later scholars, seeing this passage’s reference to 'the frog in the shallow well' and the *Xunzi*’s 'Zheng Lun' chapter, which says, 'The frog in the pit well cannot be spoken to of the joy of the Eastern Sea,' changed 'well fish' to 'well frog,' unaware that wells naturally contain fish and needed no alteration to frogs. Since this change, the world has spoken only of 'well frogs and summer insects,' forgetting the metaphor of the well fish." Wang Niansun adds, "*Xu* (dwelling) is interchangeable with *qu* (ruins), as the *Shiwen* notes, '*Xu* is also written as *qu*.' The *Guang Ya* states, '*Qu* is [居] (to dwell).' The *Wen Xuan*’s commentary on the *Xizheng Fu* cites the *Sheng Lei*: '*Qu* is the site of former dwellings.' The *jingqu* (mounds and ruins) in classical texts refer to former dwelling places. Thus, 'the well fish is confined by its dwelling' means it does not know the sea’s vastness, just as the River Lord is confined by his banks and shores."

A summer insect cannot be spoken to of ice, for it is bound by its season. Guo Qingfan explains, "Sima glosses *du* as 'thick,' but this is circuitous and unclear. The *Shuowen* defines *du* as 'firm.' In the *Lunyu*, 'firmly believing and fond of learning' means belief held fast; in the *Liji*’s 'Ru Xing,' 'firm in conduct and tireless' means conduct held fast. All that is narrow-minded and unenlightened is called *gu* (stubborn). The summer insect is blinded by its season, hence *du yu shi* (bound by its time). *Du* shares the same meaning as *ju* (confined) and *shu* (restrained) in the surrounding text."

A narrow-minded scholar cannot be spoken to of the Dao, for he is constrained by his teachings. Sima explains, "A *qushi* is a scholar from a rural backwater." Now that you have emerged from your banks and shores and beheld the great sea, you recognize your own inadequacy. You may now be spoken to of grand principles." Guo comments, "Because you know your limits, you may be told of the Dao."

Of all the waters under heaven, none is greater than the sea. The ten thousand rivers flow into it, yet it never fills, though no one knows when it will cease; its Tailu vent drains it, yet it never empties, though no one knows when it will end. The *Wen Xuan*’s commentary on the *Yangsheng Lun* cites Sima: "Tailu is where water flows out beyond the sea, also called Wujiao, located in the Eastern Sea. *Wei* (tail) refers to its position below all rivers, hence the name *wei*."

"As for *lü* (闾), it means 'gathering'—the place where waters gather in clans, hence the name. East of Fusang, there lies a stone, four myriad *li* in both breadth and thickness. All the sea’s waters that pour into it are scorched away, hence it is called *Wojiao* (沃焦)."

Note: "*Wojiao*" is also written as "*Wojiao*" (沃焦), as seen in the *Classic of Mountains and Seas*. Now that the globe is fully traversable, this account may be clarified. The seasons of spring and autumn remain unchanged, and droughts and floods go unnoticed. Such is its flow beyond the Yangtze and Yellow Rivers, immeasurable in quantity. Yet I have never boasted of this, for I compare my form to heaven and earth and receive *qi* from yin and yang. I exist between heaven and earth as a pebble or twig does in a great mountain. Even as I recognize my smallness, why should I boast?

Consider the Four Seas within heaven and earth—are they not like a tiny crevice in a vast marsh? *Shiwen* annotates: "*Lěi* (礨), Cui reads as *lěi* (垒); *kōng* (空) as *kǒng* (孔). *Lěikōng* (垒孔) means a small hole. Li explains: 'A small mound,' or alternatively, 'an anthill.'" Consider the Central States within the seas—are they not like a grain of *ti* (稊) in a great granary? *Shiwen* annotates: "Guo’s *Erya* commentary: '*Ti* resembles *bai* (稗).' *Dà* (大) reads as *tài* (泰)."

The number of things is called ten thousand, and humans occupy but one among them. All the people of the Nine Provinces, where grain is grown and boats and carts travel, still occupy but one among them. Cui explains: "*Zú* (卒) means 'all.'" Guo Songtao comments: "All the people of the Nine Provinces refers to the full count of its population. *Zú* is an exhaustive term. The vastness of the Nine Provinces and the multitude of its people, within the midst of heaven, are but one among the ten thousand things." In this comparison to the ten thousand things, are humans not like the tip of a hair on a horse’s body?

What the Five Emperors inherited, Cui explains: "*Lián* (连) means 'continued.'" What the Three Kings contended for, what the benevolent worried over, and what the capable labored for—all of it is encompassed here. Boyi rejected it to make his name, and Confucius discoursed on it to display his breadth. Their self-importance was no different from your earlier boasting of water."

The River Spirit said, "Then may I consider heaven and earth as great and the tip of a hair as small?"

The Spirit of the Northern Sea replied, "No. For things, their measures are boundless; their times, unceasing; their allotments, inconstant; and their beginnings and ends, without permanence. Thus, the great wisdom observes both far and near—knowing that wisdom is the same, it surveys both without favoring one corner. Therefore, the small is not deemed few, nor the great many, for it knows measures are boundless. It does not take size as a measure of abundance, knowing each measure suffices in its own way.

It verifies past and present, as Guo explains: "*Xiàng* (向) means 'illuminating.' *Jīn gù* (今故) refers to past and present." Thus, the distant does not vex, for though the past is remote, I am not troubled, nor do I need to follow the ancients. The near is grasped without strain, for what is close at hand is taken without striving. It knows time is unceasing.

It proves the great Dao of past and present, not taking human lifespans as the ultimate measure, for it knows time is unending. It examines fullness and emptiness, so it does not rejoice in gain nor grieve in loss, knowing allotments are inconstant. It understands the level path, as Guo explains: "Life and death are the proper course of daily renewal." Thus, it does not delight in life nor deem death a calamity, knowing beginnings and ends cannot be fixed.

Guo explains: "Understanding that beginnings and ends are ever-renewing, one knows the past cannot be clung to or detained." Consider what humans know—it is less than what they do not know. The known is finite, while the unknown is limitless! The time of one’s life is less than the time before birth. Life has an end, but heaven and earth are endless. To seek the bounds of the greatest with the smallest is to become lost and unable to find oneself. Cheng explains: "The boundless realm is not yet encompassed, while finite wisdom is already exhausted."

From this perspective, how can one know the tip of a hair suffices to define the finest boundary? Or how can one know heaven and earth suffice to exhaust the greatest realm?" The tip of a hair is not small, nor are heaven and earth great.

The River Spirit said, "Those who discuss such matters in the world all say, 'The most refined has no form, and the greatest cannot be encompassed.' Is this a true reflection of reality?"

Cheng explains: "*Xìn* (信) means 'real.'"

The Lord of the Northern Sea said: "One who views the great from the perspective of the small cannot encompass it all. As the commentary states: 'Dwelling in the small and viewing the great, there is that which cannot be fully surveyed, and so it seems impossible to encompass.' One who views the small from the perspective of the great cannot see it clearly. As the commentary states: 'Dwelling in the great and viewing the small, there is that which cannot be fully examined, and so it seems formless.' Now, the refined is the subtlest of the small, while the vast is the fullness of the great. As the commentary states: '*Fú* (垺) is pronounced like *fú* (孚) and means 'expansive.' *Yīn* (殷) means 'abundant.' Thus, the difference lies in what is convenient. As the commentary states: 'Thus, one perceives the unencompassable as inconvenient for the small, which finds convenience in its own smallness; one perceives the formless as inconvenient for the great, which finds convenience in its own greatness.' This is the nature of their circumstances. Such is the way of their conditions, and it is not worth debating.

The refined and the coarse are expected to have form. As the commentary states: 'They still seek the Dao in the realm of traces.' That which is formless cannot be divided by calculation—this refers to the refined. That which cannot be encompassed cannot be exhausted by calculation—this refers to the coarse. What can be discussed in words is the coarse aspect of things, for the coarse can still be spoken of. What can be grasped by the mind is the refined aspect of things, for the refined can still be apprehended by intention. That which cannot be discussed in words nor examined and grasped by the mind does not depend on the refined or the coarse. What does not depend on the refined or the coarse lies beyond words and intentions—this is the subtlety of the Dao.

Therefore, the conduct of the great person does not involve harming others, nor does it emphasize benevolence and kindness. It fundamentally does no harm, nor does it pride itself on benevolence and kindness. Their actions are not for profit, nor do they despise gatekeepers and servants. They fundamentally do not act for profit, nor do they regard those who seek profit—like gatekeepers and servants—as base. They do not contend over wealth and goods, nor do they exalt modesty and yielding. They do not contend over wealth, nor do they consider the virtue of modesty and yielding as lofty. In affairs, they do not rely on others, nor do they overvalue self-reliance, nor despise the greedy and corrupt. They do not borrow strength from others in their affairs but sustain themselves by their own efforts, seeking only sufficiency. Nor do they regard those who greedily seek gain as base. Their conduct differs from the common, but they do not emphasize eccentricity. Their actions do not follow the crowd, nor do they regard flattery and obsequiousness as base. They act in accord with the multitude but do not despise the fawning and sycophantic. The world’s ranks and emoluments are insufficient to encourage them; execution and disgrace are insufficient to shame them. They know that the traces of right and wrong cannot be divided, nor can the boundaries of the small and great be defined. The traces of right and wrong cannot be separated, and the beginnings of the small and great cannot be seen—only the great person knows this.

I have heard it said: As the commentary states: 'Attributed to another, hence "I have heard it said."' 'The Dao-person does not strive for fame.' Guo states: 'Allowing things to follow their own nature, so that their inherent qualities naturally manifest, then merit and fame return to things—hence, they do not strive for fame.' Note: This saying also appears in the *Shan Mu* chapter, where 'Dao' is replaced with 'perfect.' 'Perfect virtue does not attain.' Guo states: 'Attainment arises from loss. When things each have no loss, then the name of attainment vanishes.' 'The great person has no self.' Guo states: 'They simply follow things.' This is the utmost of restrained conduct." This means restraining oneself and returning to one’s proper place.

The River Spirit said: "Beyond things and within things, how do the boundaries of nobility and baseness arise? How do the boundaries of small and great arise?" He asks: If one does not depend on the refined or the coarse, then how, within the inner and outer nature of things, do the beginnings of nobility and baseness, small and great, emerge?

The Lord of the Northern Sea said: "From the perspective of the Dao, things have no nobility or baseness. From the perspective of things, each values itself and devalues others—such is the sentiment of things toward one another, hence the word 'relative.' From the perspective of custom, nobility and baseness do not lie within oneself. The world regards external honor and disgrace as nobility and baseness. From the perspective of differentiation, the numbers of gradation. Following what is great and making it great, as the commentary states: 'Taking self-sufficiency as great.' then there is nothing that is not great. Following what is small and making it small, as the commentary states: 'Taking having no excess as small.' then there is nothing that is not small. Knowing that heaven and earth are like a grain of millet and that the tip of a hair is like a mountain and hill, then the numbers of gradation are equal.

From the perspective of merit, the mutual necessity of affairs and achievements. Following what things have and making it have, then there is nothing that does not have. Following what things lack and making it lack, then there is nothing that does not lack. Su Yu states: 'The sentiment of things takes utility as having and mutual overcoming as lacking, just as arrow-makers say there can be no quivers, and quiver-makers say there can be no arrows. Yet if arrows are considered to have, then quivers, which counter arrows, can also be said to have. If quivers are considered to lack, then arrows, which are repelled by quivers, can also be said to lack.' Knowing that east and west are opposites yet cannot be without each other, then the division of merit is settled. East and west are fundamentally opposites, yet without east, west cannot be defined. Thus, speaking of their mutual necessity in terms of opposition, the division of merit can be settled.

From the perspective of tendencies, the inclinations of the multitude. Following what things affirm and affirming it, then there is nothing that is not affirmed. Following what things negate and negating it, then there is nothing that is not negated. They follow others’ right and wrong as right and wrong. Knowing that Yao and Jie each affirmed their own ways and negated the other, then the tendencies and conduct are seen. Yao negated Jie, and Jie negated Yao. Those who follow Yao and Jie each hold to their own right and wrong, so the instability of tendencies and conduct is evident.

In ancient times, Yao and Shun yielded the throne and became emperors, while Zhi and Kuai yielded and met their end. Sima states: 'King Kuai of Yan followed the advice of Su Dai, imitating Yao and Shun by yielding the throne to his minister Zi Zhi. After three years, the state fell into chaos.' Tang and Wu contended and became kings, while the Duke of Bai contended and perished." The *Shiwen* states: 'The Duke of Bai, named Sheng, was the grandson of King Ping of Chu. He caused an uprising and died.'"

"The account is found in the *Zuo Tradition*, sixteenth year of Duke Ai." From this, we see that the rites of contention and yielding, the conduct of Yao and Jie, the esteem and debasement of the times—these cannot be taken as constant.

Xuan comments: "From this, the great and small may be known." A roof beam can batter down a city wall but cannot plug a rat hole—this speaks of different implements. Guo Qingfan notes: "In the *Liezi*'s 'Tang Wen' chapter, it says: 'When Yongmen sold his song, the lingering notes coiled around the *liangli*, persisting for three days without cease.' *Liangli* is the same as *liangli*. The *Shanglin Fu* says 'intertwined *li* and *gui*,' with the commentary: '*Li* and *gui* are supports. *Li* means attachment, *gui* means crossing.' The *Guangyun* states: '*Li* means fixed.' The *Yupian* says: '*Li* means paired.' Columns are called *li* when paired, and roof beams are also called *li* when attached to one another—this refers to rafters and pillars. A *liangli* must be made of large timber, so it can be used to batter down a city wall but is not suited for trivial use."

The *Shiwen* states: "*Zhi* means to block." A fine steed like the *qiji* or *hualiu* can gallop a thousand *li* in a day, yet it is no match for a wildcat in catching mice—this speaks of different skills. The owl snatches fleas at night—The *Shiwen* notes: "The *Huainanzi* says: 'The owl gathers fleas at night, discerning the finest details.' Xu Shen explains: 'The owl gathers fleas and lice at night without fail.' The Sima edition reads: 'The owl, or *xiuliu*, hunts by night.'" Wang Yinzhi comments: "The text originally had only *chixiu*; the *xiu* character was added due to the *Shiwen*'s note 'The owl, or *xiuliu*.' The *Pi Ya* cites this passage in error. The *Shiwen* provides a pronunciation for *chi* ('*chi*, *chiyi* cut') and notes: 'Cui says: "The owl, or *xiuliu*."' It does not provide a pronunciation for *xiu*, which makes it clear that the original text did not contain it. The *Huainanzi*'s 'Zhushu' chapter also says: 'The owl snatches fleas at night.'" Note: *Ju* ("gather") is synonymous with *cuo* ("snatch"). Cui’s edition has *zui* ("best") instead of *cuo*. In ancient texts, *ju* and *zui* were often interchangeable, hence the variant. Yet though it discerns the finest details, by day it glares with wide-open eyes and cannot see a hill—this speaks of different natures.

The *Shiwen* states: "*Chen* may also be written as *ming*." Su Yu comments: "The correct reading is *chen*. It means that the owl, which discerns the finest details of fleas at night, by day glares with wide-open eyes and cannot see a hill. The *Xu Wugui* chapter says: 'The owl’s eyes have their proper time'—meaning they are suited to night and not to day."

Thus it is said: Why insist on following right and rejecting wrong, following order and rejecting chaos? Such common sayings fail to grasp the principles of Heaven and Earth or the conditions of the myriad things. It is like following Heaven and rejecting Earth, following yin and rejecting yang—its impossibility is plain. Yet such talk persists without cease, which must mean either stupidity or deceit.

Xuan comments: "The stupid do not know; the deceitful know but speak falsely." The abdications of emperors and kings differ—Cheng comments: "Some pass the throne to their clansmen, others yield it to different lineages, hence the term 'different abdications.'" The successions of the Three Dynasties differ—Cheng comments: "Some pass the throne from father to son, others seize it through armed conquest, hence the term 'different successions.'" Those who go against their times and defy custom are called usurpers. When the times and customs are already flawed, to oppose and defy them—as in the cases of Zizhi and the Duke of Bai—is to be deemed a usurper by the world. Those who act in accord with their times and follow custom are called righteous men. When the times and customs are practicable, to act in harmony with them is to be deemed righteous by the world—thus we see that esteem and debasement depend on the times.

O silent River Spirit! Beware of speaking too much. How could you know the gate of esteem and debasement, the house of great and small?"

The River Spirit said: "Then what should I do? What should I not do? How should I decide what to accept or reject, what to pursue or abandon? In the end, what am I to do?"

The Lord of the Northern Sea said: "From the perspective of the Dao, what is noble and what is base? This is called 'reversal and overflow.' As Guo notes, 'The ways of nobility and baseness follow one another in endless cycles.' Cui says, 'When nothing is noble or base, this reversal becomes beauty itself.' The original text also reads 'rebellion and overflow,' with Li explaining, 'Like boundless overflow, merging into one.' Do not constrain your will—*er*, here and below, means 'you.' When nobility and baseness have no fixed standard, one need not be bound by them. Yet this conflicts with the great Dao. Rigid adherence makes the Dao difficult to follow.

What is few and what is many? This is called 'gratitude and bestowal,' merely the gifts of Heaven. To act without unity is to be at odds with the Dao. To cling to unity in action is to fail to align with the Dao.

Majestic, like a state with its ruler, who bestows no private virtue—yet all things receive its power. Free and at ease, like the sacrifices at the earth-altar, who grants no private blessings—yet all believe the spirits bless them. Vast and boundless, like the four directions without end, without boundaries or divisions. Embracing all things, who bears or assists them? This is called 'without direction.' All things are held within me—who bears me, and who assists me? This is called 'without partiality.'

All things are equal: who is short, and who is long? As Xuan notes, 'This is why there is no direction.' The Dao has no end or beginning; things have death and birth, yet do not rely on their completion. As Xuan says, 'With life and death, the completion of things cannot be relied upon.' Emptiness and fullness alternate, never fixed in form. As Xuan says, 'Emptiness and fullness succeed one another, so form has no fixed position.'

Years cannot be lifted, time cannot be halted. As Xuan says, 'The past cannot be preserved, the passing cannot be drawn back.' Ebb and flow, fullness and emptiness—when one ends, another begins. This is how we speak of the great meaning of the Dao and discuss the principles of all things.

The birth of things is like a gallop, like a swift ride—so rapid is it. No movement without change, no moment without shift. What should be done? What should not be done? All things will transform of themselves." Cheng says, "Be at ease and let them be—they will change naturally. Why trouble yourself with doing or not doing?"

The River Spirit said: "Then what is the value of the Dao?"
Xuan says, "If there is no distinction between doing and not doing, why value the study of the Dao?"

The Lord of the Northern Sea said: "Those who know the Dao must grasp its principles; those who grasp its principles must understand its adaptability; those who understand its adaptability will not let things harm them. The one of utmost virtue cannot be burned by fire, drowned by water, harmed by cold or heat, or injured by birds and beasts. This does not mean they are merely close to these things—*bo* means 'to press.' It does not mean they are pressed near yet remain unharmed."

"To speak of discerning safety and danger, finding peace amid misfortune and fortune—Cheng says, 'Peace means tranquility. Misfortune is obstruction; fortune is smooth passage'—to be cautious in advance and retreat, none can harm you. Thus it is said: Heaven resides within—Xuan says, 'The heavenly mechanism is hidden from sight'—while man resides without—Xuan says, 'Human affairs manifest in action.' Virtue lies in Heaven. Virtue exalts the natural. To know the conduct of Heaven and man, one must root oneself in Heaven and position oneself in virtue. Only those who know the conduct of Heaven and man ground themselves in the natural and abide in self-attainment. Advancing and retreating, bending and extending—Cheng says, 'This describes the appearance of indecision. Adapting to the moment without fixed attachment'—return to the essential and speak of the ultimate." Xuan says, "This is the essence of learning and the pinnacle of the Dao."

Someone asked, "What is Heaven? What is man?"
The Lord of the Northern Sea replied, "That oxen and horses have four legs is called Heaven; to bridle a horse’s head or pierce an ox’s nose is called man. Thus it is said: Do not let man obliterate Heaven, do not let the artificial obliterate fate, do not let gain be sacrificed for fame. Do not destroy the natural with human affairs, do not injure nature with contrivance, do not let limited gain be sacrificed for boundless renown. Guard this carefully and do not lose it—this is called returning to one’s true nature." Guo says, "Truth lies within one’s inherent nature."

Kui pitied the centipede, the centipede pitied the snake, the snake pitied the wind, the wind pitied the eye, and the eye pitied the heart. Sima says, "The centipede is the *maxian* insect. The *Guangya* states, '*Ququ* is the *maxian*.' Kui has one foot, the centipede many, the snake none, the wind formless. The eye’s form is fixed here while its vision flows there; the heart’s essence is subtle, its spirit roaming beyond." Cheng says, "Pity here means admiration."

Kui said to the centipede, "I leap and stride with my single foot—Cheng says, 'Leaping and striding'—nothing is as simple as this. Now you move with ten thousand feet—how can you bear it?" He thought it cumbersome.
The centipede replied, "Not so. Have you not seen one who spits? When sprayed, the large droplets are like pearls, the small like mist, and the countless falling ones cannot be numbered. Now I move by my heavenly mechanism, yet I do not know how it is so."

The centipede said to the snake, "I move with many feet yet do not match your footlessness—how is it you are so swift?"
The snake replied, "How can one alter what the heavenly mechanism moves? What need have I for feet?"

The snake said to the wind, "I move by wriggling my spine and ribs, which seems to resemble something—it seems as if I had feet. Now you rise in a great gust from the Northern Sea and enter the Southern Sea as if nothing were there—how is this?"
The wind replied, "Indeed, I rise in a great gust from the Northern Sea and enter the Southern Sea, yet if one points a finger at me, they overcome me; if one stamps a foot at me, I too overcome them." Li notes, "The character *zhi* is also written as *cu*." Guo Songtao says, "In the *Xunzi*’s 'Strong States' chapter, Yang’s commentary on 'the great Yan follows behind' states, '*Cu* means to trample.'" Cheng says, "If a person points a finger at the wind, the wind cannot break the finger; if one stamps a foot at the wind, the wind cannot break the foot—this is the inability to overcome in small matters." "Nevertheless, to break great trees and topple great houses—only I can do this. Thus, to regard many small inabilities to overcome as a great overcoming—only the sage can achieve this." Only those who achieve great overcoming allow no room for calculation in the many small inabilities to overcome—only the sage who knows the Dao can do this. Xuan says, "The functions of the eye and heart are even more wondrous; one may understand this through personal experience, so the text omits further elaboration."

Confucius was traveling in Kuang when men of Song surrounded him several layers deep, yet he played his zither and sang without ceasing. Li notes, "Sima says, 'Song should be Wei. The people of Wei mistakenly surrounded Confucius, taking him for Yang Hu, who had once oppressed the people of Kuang.' The character *chuo* is also written as *chuo* (to cease)."
Zilu entered and saw him, saying, "How can you be so joyful, Master?"
Confucius replied, "Come! Let me tell you."

"I have long avoided poverty, yet cannot escape it—this is fate. I have long sought success, yet cannot attain it—this is timing." Cheng comments, "讳 means to shun." In the age of Yao and Shun, there were no impoverished men in the world, not because wisdom prevailed, but because worthy scholars were all employed; it was not their wisdom that brought success. In the age of Jie and Zhou, there were no successful men in the world, not because wisdom failed, but because worthy men all went into reclusion; it was not their wisdom that caused failure. Such are the circumstances of the times.

Those who travel by water without avoiding dragons and serpents possess the courage of fishermen; those who travel by land without avoiding rhinoceroses and tigers possess the courage of hunters; those who face crossed blades before them and regard death as life possess the courage of martyrs; those who know poverty is a matter of fate, success a matter of timing, and face great peril without fear possess the courage of sages. Be at peace! Rest content. My fate is governed by Heaven."

Soon after, men bearing armor approached and said, "We mistook you for Yang Hu, hence the siege. Now that we know otherwise, we beg leave to withdraw."

The armored men acknowledged their mistake and withdrew.

Gongsun Long questioned Wei Mou, saying: "In my youth, I studied the Way of the masters, and in maturity, I clarified the practice of benevolence and righteousness. I harmonized sameness and difference, blended hardness and whiteness, affirmed the untrue, and validated the invalid—confounding the wisdom of the hundred schools and exhausting the eloquence of all debaters. I took myself to have attained ultimate understanding. Yet now, having heard Zhuangzi’s words, I am bewildered and struck with wonder. I do not know whether my reasoning falls short of his, or whether my knowledge simply cannot compare. Now I am at a loss for words. I venture to ask: what is the method?"

Prince Mou leaned against his desk, sighed deeply, looked up to the sky, and laughed, saying: "Have you alone never heard of the frog in the shallow well? He said to the turtle of the Eastern Sea, ‘How joyful I am! I leap and dance upon the well’s railing, then rest at the edge of its broken bricks. When I enter the water, it supports my armpits and cradles my jaws; when I tread in the mud, it covers my feet and submerges my insteps. Looking around at the water striders, crabs, and tadpoles, none can match my delight. Moreover, to possess the water of this entire hollow and monopolize the joys of the shallow well—this is perfection itself. Why do you not come in from time to time to see for yourself?’

Before the turtle of the Eastern Sea could place its left foot inside, its right knee was already caught fast. Thereupon, it hesitated, drew back, and retreated at leisure. It then told the frog of the sea: ‘A distance of a thousand *li* cannot convey its vastness; a height of a thousand *ren* cannot measure its depth. In the time of Yu, floods prevailed for nine years out of ten, yet the waters did not increase; in the time of Tang, droughts lasted seven years out of eight, yet the shores did not diminish. Not to be altered by the brief or the enduring—

Neither by increase nor by decrease is it altered—this, too, is the great joy of the Eastern Sea."

Upon hearing this, the frog in the shallow well was struck with alarm, his countenance betraying dismay, and he lost himself in bewilderment. For one whose understanding cannot fathom the bounds of right and wrong, yet still seeks to scrutinize the words of Zhuangzi—this is like tasking a mosquito with bearing a mountain or a centipede with racing across a river; it is beyond all capacity. And one whose knowledge cannot grasp the subtleties of profound discourse, yet clings to the fleeting gains of the moment—is this not the frog in the shallow well?
