Moreover, the sea strides across the Yellow Springs and ascends the Great Vault, unbound by south or north, released in all four directions, dissolving into the unfathomable. Unrestrained by east or west, it begins in the depths of mystery and returns to the great communion. Yet you, with your narrow gaze, seek to examine it through scrutiny and pursue it through debate—this is no different from peering at the heavens through a tube or measuring the earth with an awl. Is this not petty indeed? Begone!

Have you alone not heard of the young men of Shouling who traveled to Handan to learn its famed gait? Failing to master the skill of that land, they lost even their own way of walking, and could do no better than crawl home on all fours. If you do not leave now, you will forget your own origins and lose your own calling."

At this, Gongsun Long’s mouth gaped open, unable to close, his tongue raised and unable to descend. He fled in haste.

Zhuangzi was fishing in the Pu River when the King of Chu dispatched two ministers to approach him first, saying, "We wish to burden you with the affairs of our realm." They sought to entangle him in the kingdom’s matters.

Zhuangzi held his fishing rod without turning, saying, "I have heard that in Chu there is a divine tortoise, dead now for three thousand years, which the king wraps in silk and stores in a bamboo box, enshrined in the ancestral temple. This tortoise—would it rather die to leave its bones honored, or live to drag its tail through the mud?" The two ministers replied, "It would rather live and drag its tail through the mud." Zhuangzi said, "Go away! I too shall drag my tail through the mud."

When Huizi was prime minister of Liang, Zhuangzi went to visit him. Someone told Huizi, "Zhuangzi is coming to replace you as minister." Thereupon Huizi grew fearful and searched the state for three days and three nights. Zhuangzi went to see him and said, "In the south there is a bird called the yuanchu—do you know it? The yuanchu sets out from the Southern Sea and flies to the Northern Sea. It alights only on the wutong tree, eats only bamboo fruit, and drinks only from springs of sweet wine. Then a kite, having found a rotting rat, saw the yuanchu passing by and looked up, crying, *'Ha!*' Now do you mean to frighten me with your state of Liang?"

Zhuangzi and Huizi strolled upon the bridge over the Hao River. Zhuangzi said, "The minnows swim forth so leisurely—this is the joy of fish." Huizi said, "You are not a fish—how do you know the joy of fish?" Zhuangzi replied, "You are not me—how do you know I do not know the joy of fish?" Huizi said, "I am not you, so of course I do not know you; but you are certainly not a fish, so your not knowing the joy of fish is complete." Zhuangzi said, "Let us return to the beginning. When you said, *'How do you know the joy of fish?'*—you already knew I knew it when you asked me. I know it here by the Hao."

**Outer Chapters: "Ultimate Joy" (Chapter Eighteen)**

Is there in the world an ultimate joy, or is there not? Is there a way to preserve life, or is there not? What should one do, and on what should one rely? What should one avoid, and where should one dwell? What should one pursue, and what should one reject? What should one delight in, and what should one detest?

What the world reveres are wealth, honor, longevity, and goodness—goodness meaning favorable encounters. What it delights in are bodily ease, rich flavors, fine garments, beautiful sights, and harmonious sounds. What it despises are poverty, lowliness, early death, and misfortune—early death meaning premature demise, and misfortune meaning grave illness. What it suffers from are the body’s lack of ease, the mouth’s lack of rich flavors, the form’s lack of fine garments, the eyes’ lack of beautiful sights, and the ears’ lack of harmonious sounds. If one cannot obtain these, one is filled with great sorrow and fear. How foolish it is to be so bound to the body!

The wealthy exhaust themselves with toil, amassing riches they cannot fully use—how external their concern for the body! Those of high rank pass their days and nights in anxious thought, weighing right and wrong—how distant their care for the body! When a person is born, sorrow is born with them. Even the long-lived grow muddled; enduring sorrow without dying—what misery! How far removed this is from true care for the body!

The resolute strive to be seen as good in the world, and all praise them as good. Yet this is not enough to preserve life. I do not know whether goodness is truly good or truly not good. If it is considered good, it is not enough to preserve life; if it is considered not good, it is enough to preserve others. Acting on one’s words is enough to preserve others. Thus it is said: *"When loyal admonitions go unheeded, withdraw and do not contend."* Therefore, when Wu Zixu contended and was torn limb from limb, had he not contended, his name would not have been established. Is there truly goodness, or is there not?

As for what the common world does and delights in, I do not know whether its joy is truly joy or truly not joy. I observe that what the world delights in, the masses rush toward in a frenzy, as if they cannot do otherwise. Yet all call it joy—I do not find it joyful, nor do I find it not joyful. Whether it is joy or not, I have not personally experienced it. Is there truly joy, or is there not? I take non-action as true joy, yet this is what the world deems most bitter. Thus it is said: *"Ultimate joy is no joy; ultimate praise is no praise."* Right and wrong in the world truly cannot be settled. Nevertheless, non-action can settle right and wrong. Ultimate joy and the preservation of life—only non-action comes close to attaining them.

Let me try to speak of this. Heaven is non-active and thereby clear; Earth is non-active and thereby tranquil. Thus, when the two non-actions combine, the myriad things are transformed. Vast and indistinct, how can one trace their origin? Indistinct and vast, how can one perceive their form? The myriad things, teeming and abundant, all grow from non-action. Thus it is said: *"Heaven and Earth are non-active, yet there is nothing they do not do."* Among humans, who can attain non-action?

When Zhuangzi’s wife died, Huizi came to offer condolences. Zhuangzi was squatting with his legs spread, drumming on a basin and singing. Huizi said, *"You lived with her, raised children together, and grew old. That she has died and you do not weep is enough—yet you drum on a basin and sing. Is this not excessive?"* Zhuangzi replied, *"Not so. When she first died, how could I alone not feel sorrow? But I examined her beginning and saw that originally there was no life. Not only was there no life, but originally there was no form. Not only was there no form, but originally there was no qi. Amidst the indistinct and vague, there was a transformation, and qi arose. The qi transformed and form arose. The form transformed and life arose. Now, it has transformed again into death—this is like the progression of spring, autumn, winter, and summer in the four seasons."*

A man will soon lie peacefully in the great chamber, yet I would wail and follow him in lament—this I deemed a failure to comprehend the mandate, and so I ceased.

Zhili Shu and Huajie Shu—Li notes: *"Zhili forgets form; Huajie forgets wisdom. These two have grasped transformation."* They gazed upon the mound of Mingbo—Li notes: *"A mound’s name, evoking the profound and obscure"*—and the wasteland of Kunlun, where the Yellow Emperor once rested. Suddenly, a growth sprouted on his left elbow, a tumor sounding like *"liu"* (willow), a borrowed term for *"child."* His mind was jolted, dismayed by it. Cheng notes: *"Juejue describes a startled reaction."*

Zhili Shu asked, *"Do you loathe it?"*
Huajie Shu replied, *"No. Cheng notes: *'No' means nonexistence. Why should I loathe it? Life is a temporary borrowing; that which borrows and gives rise to life is but dust and grime. Death and life are as day and night. Moreover, you and I observe transformation, and transformation has reached me—why should I loathe it?"*

When Zhuangzi traveled to Chu, he came upon an empty skull, its form stark and hollow. Xuan notes: *"Xiao (髐) is pronounced 'xiao,' meaning desiccated and hollow."* He struck it with his horsewhip—Shiwen notes: *"Jiao (撽) is pronounced 'jiao' (苦吊反). The Shuowen defines it as ● (旁击), 'to strike from the side.'"*—and then questioned it, saying: *"Did you, sir, covet life and lose the Dao, and so come to this? Or did you suffer the fall of a state, the execution of the axe and halberd, and so come to this? Or did you commit some unworthy deed, bringing shame upon your parents, wife, and children, and so come to this? Or did you endure the miseries of cold and hunger, and so come to this? Or did your years naturally reach their end and so bring you to this?"*

When he finished speaking, he took the skull as a pillow and lay down to sleep. In the dead of night, the skull appeared to him in a dream and said: *"Your words resemble those of a sophist. Yao notes: *'The Zhang Junfang edition includes the character 'xiang' (向) before 'zi' (子).'* All that you have spoken are the burdens of the living; in death, none of these remain. Would you hear the discourse on death?"*

Zhuangzi said: "Very well."

The skull replied: "In death, there is no lord above, no subject below, nor the affairs of the four seasons. Freely and at ease, one takes heaven and earth as spring and autumn. Even the joy of a king facing south cannot surpass it."

Zhuangzi did not believe him and said: "If I had the Controller of Fate restore your form, giving you bones, flesh, and skin, and return you to your parents, wife, children, village, and acquaintances, would you desire this?"

The skull knit its brows deeply and said: "How could I abandon the joy of a king facing south and return to the toils of the human world?"

---

When Yan Hui was traveling east to Qi, Confucius wore a worried look. Zigong stepped down from his mat and asked: "This disciple ventures to ask: when Hui travels east to Qi, Master, why do you wear a worried look?"

Confucius said: "Your question is excellent! Long ago, Guanzi spoke words I greatly admired: *'A small sack cannot hold a large thing; a short rope cannot draw from a deep well.'* Guo Qingfan notes: *'This saying appears in the Guanzi.'* As Guo Pu explains: *'The Yupian states: "褚 is a garment bag." The character is sometimes written as ●. The Zhongjing yinyi cites the Tongsu wen: "A garment bag is called ●." The Shuowen jizhuan says: "褚 is the sack of a garment." The Jiyun states: "It is a bag." The Zuo zhuan (Cheng 3) records: "A merchant of Zheng had a lamb he placed in a褚 to carry out." Thus, a褚 can hold things, or even a person.'*

Such is the case because destiny has its completion and form has its proper place—neither can be diminished nor augmented. I fear that Hui will speak to the Marquis of Qi of the Dao of Yao, Shun, and the Yellow Emperor, and further expound the words of Suiren and Shennong. The marquis will seek within himself and find nothing, and finding nothing, he will be perplexed. When a man is perplexed, he brings about his own death."

Ji Cheng comments: *"Finding no resolution, his heart will breed doubt. Resentful of one who surpasses him, he will surely kill Yanzi."*

"Have you alone not heard? Long ago, a seabird alighted in the outskirts of Lu. The Lord of Lu welcomed it and offered it wine in the ancestral temple. Sima Biao notes: *'The Guoyu states: "A bird called Yuanju alighted outside the eastern gate of Lu for three days, and Zang Wenzhong had the people of the state sacrifice to it." It does not mention the Lord of Lu. Yuanju, also called Zaxian, holds its head eight feet high.' The Erya, with commentary by Fan Guang, says: 'Its form resembles the phoenix.'* The lord had the *Nine Shao* performed for its entertainment and prepared a grand feast of the finest meats. The bird, however, was dazed and sorrowful, dared not eat a single morsel, nor drink a single cup, and died after three days. This was nourishing the bird with what nourishes oneself, not nourishing the bird with what nourishes a bird.

To nourish a bird with what nourishes a bird, one should let it roost in deep forests, wander on lakeside shores—Guo Pu comments: *'坛陆 refers to lake islets.'*—float upon rivers and lakes, feed on loach and whitebait—Ji Cheng comments: *'泥 refers to loach; 鲦 refers to whitebait.'*—rest in formation, and dwell at ease. The passage from *'Long ago, a seabird...'* to this point is also cited in the *Chapter on Perfecting Life.*

That one alone detests the sound of human speech—why then subject it to such clamor?" Ji Cheng comments: *"譊 means clamorous noise."*

If the music of *Xianchi* and *Nine Shao* were performed in the wilds of Dongting, birds would hear it and fly away, beasts would hear it and flee, fish would hear it and dive deep, while men, upon hearing it suddenly, would gather round to observe. Only humans delight in observing such spectacles."

Fish live in water, yet humans die in it—thus they must differ in nature. Their likes and dislikes fundamentally diverge.

Therefore, the ancient sages did not impose uniformity in abilities nor demand sameness in tasks. Ji Cheng comments: *"The sage follows the nature of things, treating people as vessels—each with distinct capacities, each suited to their disposition, each engaged in different affairs."*

Names are bounded by reality. Ji Cheng comments: *"Names arise from reality; names summon reality. Thus names are confined to reality, never seeking beyond what is real."*

Principles are established for suitability. Ji Cheng comments: *"They are set according to what is fitting, nothing more."*

This is what is called *"orderly coherence with fortune’s support."* Such a Dao may be described as one where principles flow smoothly and virtue sustains its hold.

Liezi was traveling and eating by the roadside—*The Chapter on Heavenly Portents* reads *"journeying to Wei"* instead of *"traveling."* He came upon a century-old skull,—*The Chapter on Heavenly Portents* includes *"man"* after *"came."*—plucked a clump of mugwort, and pointed to it, saying—Ji Cheng comments: *"攓 means to pull up."* *The Chapter on Heavenly Portents* reads: *"plucked mugwort, pointed, and said to his disciple Baifeng."*

*"Only you and I know that you have never died, never been born. Are you truly sorrowful? Am I truly joyful?"*

Prominent commentator Yu Yue notes: *"In the poem 'Two Sons Board a Boat,' the line '中心养养' interprets '养' as sorrow. This pairs antithetically with the following line's 'joy.'"*

The *Shiwen* records: *"The Yuanjia edition reads '若果' as '汝过' and '予过' as '子过.'"*

Editorial note: *The Chapter on Heavenly Portents* renders this as *"此过养乎，此过欢乎,"* aligning with the Yuanjia edition’s two instances of *"过,"* though the meaning remains unsatisfactory—likely corrupt.

How many kinds are there?

Ji Cheng comments: *"Yin and yang create things in endless transformation; their kinds cannot be enumerated."*

When it obtains water, it becomes *●*. The *Shiwen* notes: *"This is an ancient character for '絶,' pronounced 'jué' by Xu Shen, now read as 'jì.' Sima Biao’s edition writes '继.' Some versions render it as '断' or '续断.'"*

Lu Wenchang observes: *"The ancient character for '絶' should be '继,' making this '●' the character '继.'"*

Ji Cheng explains: *"Moist qi generates beings, proceeding from non-being to being, thus mutually continuing one another."*

Editorial note: In the *Erya*’s *"Release of Plants,"* under *"藚, 牛唇,"* Guo Pu’s commentary cites the *Mao Shi Zhuan*: *"Water plantain, resembling '续断,' jointed every inch—when plucked, it regrows."* The *Shuowen* defines *"藚"* as *"water plantain."*

Hao Yixing remarks: *"Examining today’s '马舄' growing in water, its flowers resemble plantain but larger; when plucked, it regrows joint by joint."* Thus, it is named *●*. Some versions write *"断,"* while others *"续断"*—the former likely miscopies *"●"* as *"断,"* with later hands erroneously adding *"续."*

*"藚"* resembles *"续断,"* yet the two are distinct: one grows in mountain valleys, the other in water. Its joint-by-joint regrowth, rootless and unattached to soil, earns it the name *"water plantain,"* matching the text’s *"obtains water and becomes ●."* *The Chapter on Heavenly Portents* includes the line *"if a frog becomes a quail,"* whose meaning remains unclear.

When it obtains the boundary of water and soil, it becomes *"frog-skin moss."* Sima Biao explains: *"This refers to things rooted at the water-soil boundary, spreading in water yet invisible from above. Pressing down, one finds it—like stretched silk in water, called 'frog-skin moss' in Chu."* (The Liezi commentary mistakenly writes *"土"* for *"水."*)

Ji Cheng comments: *"It is green moss, appearing in water like stretched silk—commonly called 'toad-skin moss.'"*

Editorial note: This describes growth at the water-soil boundary, not merely a waterborne plant. The *Erya*’s *"Release of Plants"* lists *"芣卫, 马舄"* and *"马舄, 车前."* Guo Pu’s commentary: *"Today’s '车前草,' with large leaves and long spikes, thrives by roadsides; in Jiangdong, it is called 'toad-skin moss.'"*

Thus, *"toad-skin moss"* is neither green moss nor what Sima Biao describes. The *Erya* also lists *"蕍, 蕮,"* with Guo Pu’s note: *"Today’s '泽蕮.'"*

Editorial note: This is *"泽泻."* The *Bencao* states: *"Also called '水舄' (i.e., '木舄')."* Tao Hongjing’s commentary: *"Narrow, long leaves, clustering in shallow water."* Su Song’s *Tu Jing*: *"Leaves resemble '牛舌草,' single and elongated; in autumn, it bears white flowers in clusters like '谷精草.' Roots are harvested in late autumn and dried."*

This obtains the water-soil boundary, hence its harvestable roots. The *Wen Xuan* commentary cites Han Ying’s *Han Shi Zhangju*: *"芣卫 is '泽舄.'"*

Lu Ji’s *Mao Shi Caomu Niao Shou Chong Yu Shu* notes: *"马舄, called '牛舌草' in Youzhou."* Given their similar leaves and the interchangeable names *"水舄,"* *"泽舄,"* and *"芣卫,"* the name *"toad-skin moss"* was mistakenly applied to the roadside *"陵舄,"* a confusion now unquestioned.

When it grows on dry mounds, it becomes *"mound plantain."* Sima Biao explains: *"Things formed by water but born on land, growing on mounds, transform into '车前'—renamed '陵舄.' Also called '泽舄,' changing with moisture or dryness."* (This further evidences name conflation.)

Editorial note: Zhang Zhan’s *Liezi* commentary defines *"陵屯"* as *"high, pure places,"* generally meaning waterless terrain. The *Shi*’s *"芣卫"* *Shiwen* cites Lu Ji: *"牛舌, also called '当道.'"*

Han Ying’s *Han Shi* states: *"Growing straight is '车前'; growing crooked is '芣卫.'"*

This distinguishes growth in straight paths from roadsides. *"Straight path"* and *"当道"* align with *"grows on dry mounds."* *"陵舄"* obtaining *"郁栖"* becomes *"crow’s foot."*

Sima Biao explains: *"郁栖 is an insect’s name; '乌足' is a plant growing by water. When '郁栖' dwells in '陵舄,' it transforms into '乌足.'"*

Li comments: *"郁栖 is manure. '陵舄' in manure becomes '乌足.'"*

Editorial note: *"郁栖"* is manure, not an insect—see below. The root of *"乌足"* becomes *"蛴螬."* Sima Biao’s edition writes *"螬蛴,"* explaining: *"A scorpion."*

Editorial note: *"蛴螬"* and *"螬蛴"* are distinct. The *Erya*’s *"Release of Insects"* lists *"蟦蛴螬,"* with Guo Pu’s note: *"Found in dung."* It also lists *"蝤蛴, 蝎,"* with Guo Pu: *"Found in wood. Though both are called '蝎,' their habitats differ."*

The *Shi*’s *"领如蝤蛴"* appears in Cai Yong’s *Qing Yi Fu* as *"领如螬蛴,"* showing *"蝤"* and *"螬"* are interchangeable. The *Shuowen* defines *"蝤"* as *"蝤●"* and *"蝎"* as *"蝤●."* It further states: *"● is ●●"* and *"● is ●●."*

Based on this, we know that Sima’s edition erroneously conflates the two. The *Shuowen* lacks the character 「蟦」; 「蟦」 is likely a phonetic loan for 「粪」. Since "乌足" arises from "陵舄" in manure-rich soil, its root dwelling in dung and emerging as "蛴螬" is all the clearer. The *Bencao* states: *"蟦蛴 grows in the marshes of Henan and in household dung heaps; those that move backward are superior."* Tao’s commentary: *"蛴 is also written as 蠀."* The *Fangyan*: *"蠀螬 is called 蟦."* 「蛴」 and 「蠀」 share the same initial consonant. Its leaves become butterflies. The largest are as big as a thumb, moving by their arms—faster than by foot—from summer into autumn, transforming into cicadas. The *Lunheng*’s "Wu Xing" chapter states: *"蛴螬 transforms into 复育, and 复育 transforms into cicadas,"* which confirms this. Butterflies, *xu* also, transform into insects. The *Shiwen* notes: *"Butterflies are also called 胥."* Yu Yue comments: *"‘胥也’ should be read together with ‘化而为虫,’ parallel to the following ‘鸲掇千日为鸟.’ ‘千日为鸟’ speaks of its slowness, while ‘胥也化而为虫’ speaks of its swiftness."* The *Tianrui* chapter’s *Shiwen* explains: *"胥 means ‘little,’ referring to a short time."* It is born beneath the stove, resembling a molted skin (蜕). Its name is *quduo*. In the *Tianrui* chapter, 「鸲」 is written as 「鸲」, the same character. *Quduo* after a thousand days—Zhang’s commentary: *"dies after a thousand days"*—becomes a bird called *ganyugu*. The *Tianrui* chapter includes the words 「化而」 before 「为」. The saliva of *ganyugu* becomes *simi*. Li explains: *"Saliva is the fluid in its mouth."* *Simi* becomes *shixi*. Yi Lücheng comments: *"The midges in vinegar jars also become vinegar flies."* Born from *shixi*, *huangkuang*—the *Tianrui* chapter adds the four characters 「食酰颐辂」 before 「生」. Born from *jiuyou*—the *Tianrui* chapter adds the four characters 「食酰黄軦」 before 「生」. *Maorui* is born from *fuxuan*. Cheng comments: *"Fuxuan refers to fireflies, also said to be the insects of powdered mice."* The *Shiwen*: *"Pronounced *quan*, Guo pronounces it *huan*. The *Erya* states: ‘Also called 守瓜.’ Another says: powdered mice."* Note: The *Tianrui* chapter includes the sentence 「九猷生乎瞀芮」 above this, with Zhang’s commentary: *"蠸 pronounced *quan*, referring to the yellow beetle in melons."* "羊奚 compared to 不●"—"old bamboo gives birth to 青宁." The *Shiwen* treats 「羊奚比乎不●」 and 「久竹生青宁」 as separate sentences. Sima comments: *"羊奚 is a plant whose root resembles turnip; when it combines with old bamboo, they form a new entity, both arising from disparate kinds. 青宁 is an insect’s name."* Sima thus reads 「久竹」 as part of the preceding clause. Zhang Zhan’s reading aligns with Lu’s, with the note for 「羊奚」: *"These are different kinds that form an affinity,"* and for 「久竹」: *"It arises in forests and marshes."* Neither offers a definitive explanation, so it remains unclear which is correct. Additionally, the *Tianrui* chapter includes twenty-two sentences from 「羊肝化为地皋」 to 「酰鸡生乎酒」 above this, which *Zhuangzi* omits. 青宁 gives birth to *cheng*. Cheng comments: *"A red insect’s name."* *Cheng* gives birth to horses, horses give birth to humans, and humans eventually return to the *mechanism*. Yu Yue comments: *"‘又’ should be ‘久,’ a scribal error. ‘久’ means ‘old.’"* The *Tianrui* chapter reads: *"人久入于机."* All things emerge from the *mechanism* and return to the *mechanism*.

**Outer Chapters, "Da Sheng" (Chapter 19)**

Those who grasp the essence of life do not pursue what is useless to life. Xuan comments: *"Pursuing useless nourishment is what life has no need for."* Those who grasp the essence of fate do not pursue what knowledge cannot alter. Xuan comments: *"What numbers cannot compel is what knowledge cannot alter."* Nurturing the body must begin with material things. Cheng comments: *"Referring to wealth, clothing, and food."* Yet there are cases where material things are abundant but the body is not nourished. Xuan comments: *"Ultimately, material things are insufficient to nourish the body."* Life must first avoid separation from the body, yet there are cases where the body remains intact but life perishes. Xuan comments: *"Ultimately, the body is insufficient to preserve life."* Life’s arrival cannot be refused, nor its departure halted. How lamentable! The world believes that nurturing the body is enough to preserve life, yet nurturing the body is ultimately insufficient to preserve life—then what in the world is worth doing? Though it is insufficient, it cannot be left undone. One cannot escape doing it. Cheng comments: *"Extraneous matters are insufficient to pursue, but essential matters cannot be neglected."* To avoid serving the body, nothing is better than abandoning the world. Abandoning the world means no entanglements; no entanglements means equilibrium. Xuan comments: *"Wandering on level ground."* Equilibrium means alternating renewal with it. Xuan comments: *"Alternating renewal with the transformations of creation."* Alternating renewal brings one close to it. Xuan comments: *"Approaching the Dao."* What need is there to abandon affairs or forsake life? Cheng comments: *"The world’s emptiness—what need is there to discard it? Life’s illusions—what need is there to forget them?"* Abandoning affairs means the body is not wearied; forsaking life means the essence is not depleted. When the body is whole and essence restored, one becomes one with Heaven. Xuan comments: *"Aligning with the spontaneity of creation."* Heaven and Earth are the parents of the myriad things: when united, they form a body; when dispersed, they become a beginning. Xuan comments: *"What disperses here becomes the beginning of what forms there."* When body and essence are not depleted, this is called *transformation*.

Shifting the power of creation itself. Refining essence ever more finely, one returns to assist Heaven. Xuan comments: *"To cultivate essence to its utmost is to rely on its participation in transformation and nurturance."*

Master Liezi asked Master Guan Yin: Li comments: *"Guan Yin is Guan Yinxi, the Magistrate of the Pass."* Cheng comments: *"His surname was Yin, given name Xi, courtesy name Gongdu. As Magistrate of Hangu Pass, he was called Master Guan Yin. A perfected person and disciple of Laozi, he embraced the Dao and held fast to virtue—hence Liezi’s inquiry."*

"The perfected person moves in concealment without obstruction." Cheng comments: *"Moving through the world in concealment, undeterred by external conditions."* Note: The *Liezi*’s *Huangdi* chapter reads *"without emptiness."* "They tread through fire without scorching, walk above the myriad things without trembling." Cheng comments: *"Unmoved by cold or heat, fire cannot harm them; undisturbed by high or low, their hearts know no fear."*

"May I ask how one attains this?"

Master Guan Yin replied: *"This is the preservation of pure qi, not the domain of cunning knowledge or bold decisiveness."* Cheng comments: *"This is the safeguarding of pure, harmonious qi—not something attained through cleverness, deceit, or reckless courage."*

"Sit! I shall tell you."

"All that possesses form, appearance, sound, or color belongs to the realm of things—how can one thing distance itself from another? Guo comments: *'Only the heartless stand apart.'* Why should any suffice to precede the rest? They are but forms, nothing more. Guo comments: *'All are but things of shape and color; none suffice to surpass the others.'* Yao comments: *'The Jiangnan edition includes the character "form" above "color."'*

Thus, things extend into the formless and rest where transformation ceases. As Liezi’s Zhang Zhan annotates: *'If existence has no beginning, then what it creates is formless; if form has no end, then where it rests knows no transformation.'* One who attains this and exhausts its depths—Guo comments: *'To fathom this with the heart.'*—how could things ever constrain them? Cheng comments: *'Not bound by things.'* Note: The *Huangdi* chapter omits the character *'thing'* and erroneously renders *'and rest'* as *'is called.'*

They dwell within the measure of no excess, Guo comments: *'Abiding within the bounds of what is received.'* Note: The *Huangdi* chapter erroneously renders *'excess'* as *'profound.'* Concealing themselves in the traceless principle, Guo comments: *'Obscurely renewing with the transformations of each day.'* Roaming where the myriad things begin and end, Guo comments: *'Beginning and end are the limits of things.'* They unify their nature, Guo comments: *'Adornment divides it.'* Nurture their qi, Guo comments: *'Not employing the heart to command it.'* Harmonize their virtue, Guo comments: *'Not allowing things to sever their nature.'* Note: The *Huangdi* chapter renders *'harmonize'* as *'contain.'* Thus, they commune with the source of all things. Cheng comments: *'The source of all things is spontaneity. Having unified their nature and harmonized their virtue, they respond to things—hence they penetrate the origin of the Dao and commune with the root of spontaneity.'*

Those who are thus preserve the wholeness of Heaven; their spirit knows no fissure—no gap. How could things ever intrude? External afflictions cannot enter."

The drunkard who falls from a carriage, though moving swiftly, does not die. His bones and joints are the same as others’, yet the harm he suffers differs—his spirit remains whole. He is unaware of riding, unaware of falling; death and life, terror and fear do not enter his breast. Thus, he encounters things without dread.

*Shiwen* notes: *"‘遌’ is pronounced ‘wu.’ The *Erya* states: ‘遌 means opposition.’ Guo’s commentary says: ‘It refers to clashing or striking.’ ‘慑’ means fear."* Lu comments: *"Modern editions write it as ‘悟.’"*

If one preserved in wine can be thus, how much more so one preserved in Heaven! The sage hides within Heaven, and so nothing can harm him.

Here ends the quotation from the *Liezi*.

The avenger does not break the *Mo* or *Gan* swords—*Mo Ye* and *Gan Jiang*. Even one with a vengeful heart does not resent a flying tile; thus, the world remains in balance. Therefore, there are no wars of aggression, no punishments of slaughter—this is the Way’s doing.

Do not open the Heaven of man, but open the Heaven of Heaven. Guo comments: *"Knowing without deliberation is opening the Heaven of Heaven; responding after cognition is opening the Heaven of man. Thus, opening the Heaven of Heaven is the movement of nature; opening the Heaven of man is the function of knowledge."* Opening the Heaven of Heaven gives birth to virtue. Guo comments: *"When nature moves, it meets things appropriately, is content and forgets excess—thus virtue is born."* Opening the Heaven of man gives birth to harm. Guo comments: *"When knowledge is employed, it seeks from stimuli and wearies without cease—thus harm is born."*

Do not weary of Heaven, do not neglect man: constantly guard Heaven’s virtue—this is not wearying of Heaven; intelligence illuminates things—this is not neglecting man. The people thus draw near to their true nature. *"几"* means "near."

Cheng comments: *"When all under heaven abide in the genuine, the living world knows no artifice."*

Confucius, traveling to Chu, emerged into a forest and saw a hunchbacked man catching cicadas with a pole, as if merely plucking them. Cheng comments: *"痀偻 describes the bent posture of an old man. 承蜩 means catching cicadas with a pole. 掇 means to pick up."* Guo Qingfan notes: *"承 should be read as 拯, meaning to draw or take. The *Shuowen* writes it as ●. In the *Liezi*'s 'Yellow Emperor' chapter, it says: 『He had his disciples stand side by side in the current to 承 them.』 The *Exegesis* reads: 『承 is pronounced as 拯.』"* Note: In the "Yellow Emperor" chapter, "偻" is written as "慺," a loan character.

Confucius said: *"Are you skillful? Do you have a Dao?"*
The man replied: *"I have a Dao. For five or six months, I stack pellets [on the pole]. Sima comments: 『Five or six months is the time when cicadas cling [to branches]. 累丸 means stacking them on the tip of the pole.』"* Note: In the "Yellow Emperor" chapter, "累丸" is written as "累垸."
*"If I stack two and they do not fall, I lose only a trifling amount—perhaps two or three out of ten. If I stack three and they do not fall, I lose only one in ten. If I stack five and they do not fall, it is as if I am merely plucking them. I position my body as if it were a withered stump, fixed in place."* The *Exegesis* notes: *"厥 may be written as 橛 in some editions."* Note: This refers to a wooden stake. *"株 means the root of a tree. The body is held as firmly as a stake or root."* In the "Yellow Emperor" chapter, it is written as "橛株驹," using loan characters.
*"I hold my arm as if it were a branch of withered wood."* Guo comments: *"This is the height of stillness."*
*"Though the greatness of heaven and earth and the multitude of the ten thousand things, I am aware of nothing but the cicada’s wings. I do not turn or shift my focus; I do not let the ten thousand things replace the wings of the cicada. How could I fail to succeed!"*

Confucius turned to his disciples and said: *"Where intention is undivided, it congeals into spirit."* In the "Yellow Emperor" chapter, "凝" is written as "疑," which is correct. Below, *"the ferryman steers the boat as if by spirit,"* *"onlookers are startled as if by ghosts or spirits,"* and *"the vessel’s ability to 疑神"* all share this meaning of "疑于神." *"This is what the hunchbacked elder means!"*

Yan Yuan asked Confucius, saying: *"I once crossed the Shāngshēn Abyss—Cheng comments: 'The name of an abyss in the state of Song.'—where the ferryman steered the boat as if by spirit. I asked him, 'Can one learn to steer a boat?' He replied, 'Yes. Those skilled in swimming can do so quickly. Those adept at floating on water can master it through repeated practice. The 'Yellow Emperor' chapter contains the line: *"Those who can swim may be taught."* As for divers, they have never seen a boat before yet can steer it at once.' A diver is one who can submerge in water. Though he has never seen a boat before, once aboard, he can steer it immediately. In the 'Yellow Emperor' chapter, 'at once' is written as 'sù,' with the note: 'Sù means to rise.' I asked him further, but he would not tell me. May I ask what this means?"*

Confucius said: *"Those skilled in swimming can do so quickly because they have forgotten the water. The 'Yellow Emperor' chapter adds two lines before this: *'Those who can swim may be taught, for they treat water lightly.'* As for the diver who has never seen a boat yet can steer it at once, he regards the abyss as if it were a hill, without fear of capsizing or drowning. He sees the boat’s overturning as no different from his carriage rolling backward. Guo comments: *'He regards the boat’s overturning in the abyss as a carriage rolling back on a slope.'* Though a thousand shifts and turns lie before him, none can enter his dwelling—The 'Yellow Emperor' chapter includes the word 'things' after 'thousand,' which is correct. The lines read: *'Though a thousand things overturn and shift, none can enter his dwelling.'* Yu comments: *'Fāng means "alongside." Its original meaning refers to two boats sailing side by side, hence it carries the sense of "together." Fāng chén hū qián means "a thousand things arrayed together before him."'* Zhang’s note: *'The abode of spirit is called a dwelling.'* Where could he not find leisure?—Xuan comments: *'With spirit settled, one is at ease everywhere.'*

One who stakes earthenware becomes skillful—Li comments: *'Zhù means to strike.'* Cheng comments: *'When gambling with worthless clay vessels, one feels no attachment, hence strikes with skill.'* Note: In the 'Yellow Emperor' chapter, 'zhù' is written as 'kōu,' with Zhang’s note: *'Kōu means to probe. To hide an object in the hand and retrieve it by probing.'* One who stakes a hook grows cautious—Cheng comments: *'When gambling with a hook, the object is slightly more valuable, so one fears missing and thus becomes timid and unsteady.'* Note: Zhang’s note: *'A hook is made of silver or bronze.'* One who stakes gold becomes ●—The *Shiwen* notes: *'One version writes ●. The *Shuowen* says: "● means confusion." The Yuánjiā edition writes "hūn."'* Note: The 'Yellow Emperor' chapter writes 'hūn.' Guo comments: *'The greater the stake, the more one clings to it.'* Though their skill is the same, where there is attachment, one values externals. Whoever values externals is clumsy within."*

Tian Kaizhi was received by Duke Wei of Zhou.

Cui’s edition reads *"Duke Wei of Zhou’s Kitchen."* Yu comments: *"The *Records of the Grand Historian* does not record the name of Duke Wei, son of Duke Huan of Western Zhou; Cui’s edition may thus supplement the historical lacuna."* Duke Wei said: *"I have heard that Zhu Shen studies the Way of nurturing life."* Sima comments: *"Studies the Dao of nourishing life."* *"You, sir, have associated with Zhu Shen—what have you learned from him?"* Tian Kaizhi replied: *"Kaizhi merely grasps a broom to lean against the gate and courtyard."* Cheng comments: *"A broom for sweeping."* *"What could I have learned from the Master!"* Duke Wei said: *"Do not decline, Master Tian! I wish to hear it."* Kaizhi said: *"I heard the Master say: ‘One who is good at nurturing life is like a shepherd: he watches those lagging behind and whips them.’"* Guo Songtao comments: *"To whip those behind is to make those ahead proceed at ease."* Note: The meaning is to carefully maintain one’s end. Duke Wei said: *"What does this mean?"*

Tian Kaizhi said: "In Lu there was a man named Shan Bao who dwelt in the cliffs and drank from streams, sharing no profit with the people. At seventy he still had the complexion of an infant, yet by misfortune he met a starving tiger, and the tiger killed and ate him." Su Yu comments: "This speaks of failing to guard against peril on the road." "There was also a man named Zhang Yi who, whether at high gates or curtained thresholds—Xuan comments: 'High gates refer to great households; hanging curtains to screen the door belong to lesser ones'—would run wherever he went. Lüshi Chunqiu's 'Must Oneself' chapter says: 'Zhang Yi was fond of reverence; at gates, curtains, or gatherings, he would hasten without fail.' Gao You notes: 'He would hasten whenever passing by.' The *Huainanzi*'s 'Among Men' chapter states: 'Zhang Yi was fond of reverence; passing palaces or ancestral temples, he would hasten; seeing gates or crowds, he would dismount. Even with grave-diggers or grooms, he exchanged courtesies, yet he did not live out his years, dying of internal heat.'" Yu comments: "‘Run’ is a corrupted form of ‘hasten.’ The *Zhuangzi*’s text is incomplete, so scholars cannot grasp its meaning." At forty he suffered from the illness of internal heat and died. This speaks of exhausting the body to no benefit. Bao nurtured his inner self yet was devoured by a tiger from without; Yi nurtured his outer self yet was assailed by illness from within. These two men both failed to whip those lagging behind."

Confucius said: "Do not retreat into concealment, do not emerge into exposure—Xuan comments: 'Lest one be either too still or too active'—but stand like a withered tree at the center. Xuan comments: 'Like a hollowed tree without intent, standing amid motion and stillness.' If these three are attained, one’s name will surely reach its utmost. Xuan comments: 'One may be called a perfected person.' Now, those who fear the road: if one in ten is killed, fathers and sons, brothers and kin will warn one another, and only with a great host of armed men will they dare to venture forth—are they not wise? Yet what men truly ought to fear—Su Yu comments: 'Ought most to fear'—lies upon their sleeping mats and between their meals, yet they do not guard against it. This is their error." Guo comments: "Though only one in ten is killed, they greatly fear it. But the harms of sensual desire all lead to death, and none fail to rush headlong into them. This is a far greater error."

The invocator, clad in dark ceremonial robes, approached the pen and addressed the pig—Cheng comments: "The invocator is the invocator-priest. *Xuan duan* is his ritual attire. The *ce* is the pen. Before the sacrifice, he approaches the pen to address the pig, as the text below records"—saying: "Why do you hate death? I shall nourish you for three months—*Shiwen* notes: '● is pronounced *huan*; Sima explains: "To nourish."'—then purify you for ten days, fast for three, spread white couch-grass, and place your shoulder and rump upon the carved sacrificial platter. Would you prefer that?" Speaking on the pig’s behalf, he said: "Better to eat chaff and husks and be left in the pen." *Cuo* means to place. He thus imagined the pig’s preference. But if planning for himself, he would accept a life of noble rank with carriages and caps, and in death be placed upon the hearse—*Sima* comments: "*Dun* is like *zhuan* (carved); *dun* is like *an* (table)." Wang Niansun comments: "*Dun* should be read as *quan*, meaning the funeral carriage. The *Zaji* states: 'The coffin is carried on the *tui* carriage,' and Zheng Xuan notes: '*Tui* is read as *quan*.' *Shiwen* notes: '*Quan* is pronounced *shi zhuan* or *shi zhuan*.' The *Rites of Zhou* calls it the *shen* carriage; the *Zaji* calls it the *tuan*. It may be written as *quan* or *tuan*, with similar pronunciation and meaning. Here it is written *dun*, but the sense is the same. *Dun* should be read as *chun*, also a funeral carriage. *Chun* and *dun* are interchangeable, as evidenced by Zheng Xuan’s note in the *Zaji*: 'The coffin is carried on the *dun*.'"—amid the gathered trappings—*Shiwen* notes: "One explanation: *lou* should be *lou*, pronounced *li jiu*. It refers to burial amid rushes and reeds." Wang Niansun comments: "*Julou* refers to the ornaments of the funeral carriage. Many adornments are gathered, hence *ju* (gathered); its shape is high in the center and low at the edges, hence *lou* (bent). The *Shiming* states: 'The carriage for the coffin has a cover called *liu*. *Liu* means gathered, for many adornments are gathered, and its shape is bent.' The *Tan Gong* speaks of 'setting up *lou sha*'; the *Xunzi*'s 'Discourse on Ritual' writes it as *lü sha*; the *Lüshi Chunqiu*'s 'Moderating Mourning' chapter writes it as *lou sha*. *Liu*, *lou*, *lou*, and *lü* are variant characters with the same meaning."—without regard for future consequences.

To plan for a pig is to discard it; to plan for oneself is to keep it—how does one differ from a pig?

Duke Huan was hunting in the marshes, with Guan Zhong driving his chariot, when he saw a ghost. The Duke grasped Guan Zhong’s hand and asked, “What did you see, Father Zhong?” Guan Zhong replied, “*Your servant* saw nothing.” The Duke returned, stricken with *eiyi* illness, and for days did not emerge.

*Shiwen* notes: “*Ei* is pronounced *yudai*; Guo reads it as *xi*. *Yi* is pronounced *tudai*; Guo reads it as *yi*. Li reads it as *tai*, explaining: ‘*Eiyi* refers to the loss of one’s hun and po.’”

A Qi scholar named Huangzi Gao’ao said, “If the Duke has harmed himself, how can a ghost harm him?—*Sima* notes: ‘Huang is the surname; Gao’ao is the courtesy name.’ When the *qi* of pent-up anger disperses and does not return, it results in deficiency. Li explains: ‘*Fen* means fullness; *chu* means coagulation. When the spirit is obstructed, yin and yang congeal within, while hun and po scatter without—hence the term *deficiency*.’ If it ascends but does not descend, it makes one prone to anger; if it descends but does not ascend, it makes one prone to forgetfulness. Li explains: ‘When yang scatters and yin congeals, anger arises; when yin surges and yang recedes, forgetfulness follows.’ If it neither ascends nor descends but lodges in the body at the heart, it causes illness.” Li explains: “When above and below fall out of harmony, yin and yang contend and attack the heart—the heart being the master of spirit—hence illness.”

Duke Huan asked, “Then do ghosts exist?” The scholar replied, “They do.”

"In the depths there are *lü*; in the stove there are *ji*."
Annotation: "Sima’s version reads ‘In the depths there are *lou*’: ‘*Shen* (depths) refers to muddy water. *Lou* is the name of a spirit. *Ji* is the Stove God, clad in red, resembling a beautiful woman.’"

Within the filth of doorways, *Leiting* dwells.
Annotation: "Amid the foul soil inside gates, there is a ghost named *Leiting* (Thunderclap)."

In the northeast below, *Bei’a* and *Xielong* leap.
Annotation: "*Long* is pronounced as in *dragon*. Sima says: ‘*Bei’a* is the name of a spirit. *Xielong* resembles a child, one foot four inches tall, dressed in black with a red headscarf, wearing a large cap, bearing a sword and halberd.’"

In the northwest below, *Yiyang* resides.
Sima says: "*Yiyang* has the head of a leopard and the tail of a horse—some say a dog’s head, others that it is the name of a spirit."

In water there is *Wangxiang*."
Sima’s version reads "*Wushang*": "It resembles a child, with a reddish-black complexion, red claws, large ears, and long arms. Some say it is the name of a water spirit."

On mounds there is *Shen*."
Annotation: "An alternate version writes this as *shen*. Sima says: ‘It resembles a dog, with horns and a five-colored patterned body.’"

On mountains there is *Kui*."
Annotation: "It resembles a drum, with a single foot."

In the wilds there is *Panghuang*."
Annotation: "An alternate version writes this as *Fanghuang*. Sima says: ‘*Fanghuang* resembles a snake, with two heads and a five-colored pattern.’"

In marshes there is *Weiyi*."

The Duke said, "May I ask what *Weiyi* looks like?"
Prince Huangzi replied, "*Weiyi* is as large as a wheel hub and as long as a carriage shaft, clad in purple with a vermilion cap. As a creature, it is fearsome. When it hears the sound of a thunder chariot, it clasps its head and stands upright. Those who see it are nearly destined for hegemony."

Duke Huan burst into laughter and said, "This is what *I* have seen!"

The *Shiwen* annotation states: "‘Vermilion cap’—Sima’s version writes this as ‘*Yu* cap,’ explaining: ‘A cap from the state of *Yu*, its design resembling a conch shell.’"

Thereupon, [the Duke] straightened his robes and cap and sat with him. Before the day was out, he was unaware that his illness had departed.

Guo [Xiang] comments: "This speaks of how those who become burdened by worries when they arise fail to understand; those who regain their nature when troubles depart have attained the principle."

Master Jisheng reared fighting cocks for the king.

The *Shiwen* annotation states: "*Jisheng*—a person’s name. An alternate version writes this as *Xiao*." The *Liezi*’s "Yellow Emperor" chapter writes this as "King Xuan of Zhou."

After ten days, [the king] asked, "Is the cock ready?"

The "Yellow Emperor" chapter includes the words "fit for fighting" after "cock," which are omitted here.

[Jisheng] replied, "Not yet. It is still hollow and arrogant, relying on its qi."

Zhang’s commentary: "One without substance who prides itself."

After another ten days, [the king] asked again.

[Jisheng] replied, "Not yet. It still responds to calls and glances at shadows."

Li comments: "It responds to crowing and turns to look at its shadow."

After another ten days, [the king] asked again.

[Jisheng] replied, "Not yet. It still glares fiercely and bristles with qi."

Zhang’s commentary: "It constantly seeks opponents and insists on its own victory."

After another ten days, [the king] asked again.

[Jisheng] replied, "Almost. Though other cocks may crow, it no longer changes. As Zhang’s commentary states: ‘It ignores challenges from opponents and forgets victory or defeat.’ When viewed, it resembles a wooden cock; its virtue is complete."

Xuan comments: "Its spirit is concentrated and still."

No other cock dared respond—all turned and fled.

Guo [Xiang] comments: "Even one reared to perfection remains unmatched—how much more so one that perfects itself!"

Note: The "Yellow Emperor" chapter writes "耳" (*er*) instead of "矣" (*yi*).

Confucius observed the Lüliang Falls.

Sima comments: "A place where the river cuts through stone. Today, west of Lishi County in Xihe, there is such a gorge, commonly called Huangliang. The *Huainanzi* states: ‘In ancient times, the Dragon Gate was not yet carved, and the river flowed above Meng Gate.’"

Cheng comments: "Some say that two hundred *li* from Puzhou there is the Dragon Gate, where the river passes through a waterfall—also called Lüliang. Others say it refers to Lüliang in Pengcheng County of Song."

The water plunged thirty *ren* in height, its froth surging forty *li*, a place where soft-shelled turtles, alligators, fish, and turtles could not swim. He saw a man swimming there and assumed he was in distress, wishing to die—drowning himself out of sorrow or misfortune. He ordered his disciples to follow the current and rescue him.

(Note: "并" and "傍" are interchangeable.)

The *Huangdi* chapter writes "承" instead, an ancient interchangeable character. After several hundred paces, he emerged, hair unbound, singing as he swam beneath the embankment. The *Huangdi* chapter writes "棠行" instead.

Confucius followed and questioned him, saying: "I took you for a ghost, but observing you, you are a man. May I ask if there is a Dao for treading water?"

He replied: "No—there is no Dao for me. I began with the familiar, grew with my nature, and fulfilled my destiny. I enter with the whirlpool and emerge with the surge. I follow the Way of water without imposing my will." Sima Biao comments: "齐 refers to the swirling water like a mill’s wheel." Guo Qingfan notes: "齐 is the center of things. The *Han Shu: Jiaosi Zhi* states, ‘齐 is so called because it aligns with the celestial center,’ with Su Lin’s commentary: ‘It is the central alignment of heaven.’" Wang Niansun observes: "The navel resides in the center of the abdomen, hence it is called 脐. 脐 is 齐." Xuan comments: "The water’s vortex resembles a navel." (Note: The *Huangdi* chapter writes "赍" in error.) Sima Biao comments: "汩 is the surging wave." Guo Xiang remarks: "One surrenders to the water rather than to oneself. This is how I tread it."

Confucius asked: "What do you mean by beginning with the familiar, growing with your nature, and fulfilling your destiny?"

He replied: "I was born on the mound and am at ease on the mound—this is the familiar. I grew up in the water and am at ease in the water—this is my nature. I do not know why I am so, yet I am so—this is destiny." Guo Xiang comments: "When one possesses a particular ability and applies it where it belongs, nothing in the world is difficult. Using what is not difficult to traverse the Way of life and generation, where can one not pass through?"

A certain edition originally wrote "thousand" (千) in place of "ten" (十), which has been emended according to the *Jishi* edition and *Shiwen*.

Ziqing carved wood into a *ju*. Li [Yi] notes: "A grand artisan of Lu. *Zi* is an official title; Qing is his name." Yu [Yue] remarks: "The *Zuozhuan* of Duke Xiang’s fourth year mentions 'Artisan Qing,' who is this man." Sima [Biao] comments: "The *ju* is a musical instrument resembling the *jiazhong* bells." *Shiwen* reads: "Pronounced *ju*."

When the *ju* was completed, those who saw it marveled as though it were the work of ghosts or spirits. The Lord of Lu saw it and asked, "What technique did you use to make it?" He replied: "I am but a craftsman—what technique could I possess? Nevertheless, there is one thing. When I set out to make a *ju*, I never dared to deplete my *qi*, for Li [Yi] notes: '*Qi* depleted, the mind stirs; the mind stirred, the spirit is not concentrated.' I must fast to still my mind.

After fasting for three days, I dared not entertain thoughts of rewards, ranks, or emoluments." Xuan [Ying] comments: "Forgetting profit."

After five days, I dared not entertain thoughts of praise or blame, skill or clumsiness." Xuan comments: "Forgetting reputation."

After seven days, I suddenly forgot I had limbs and a body." *Shiwen* reads: "*Zheran*: a state of immobility." Xuan comments: "Forgetting the self."

At that time, I had no thought of court or state." Xuan comments: "Forgetting power. As if I were not carving for the public."

My skill became concentrated, and all external distractions dissolved." *Shiwen* reads: "*Gu* (bone) is also written as *hua* (distraction)." Cheng [Xuanying] comments: "*Hua* means disorder." Xuan comments: "All external matters that disturb the mind were dispelled."

Only then did I enter the mountain forest to observe the natural qualities of the wood." Xuan comments: "Examining the wood’s inherent grain."

When the form and material were perfectly matched, the *ju* appeared in its entirety—as if the whole instrument were already before my eyes. Only then did I apply my hands to it; thus I proceeded with the work. If not, I would abandon it." Otherwise, I would set it aside.

Thus I aligned the Heaven in me with the Heaven in the wood—my own Heaven encountering the wood’s Heaven. Is this not why the instrument seems divinely wrought?

This speaks of how following its nature makes craftsmanship as if spirit-like, while defying its nature labors the mind and mars the self. Dongye Ji demonstrated his charioteering before Duke Zhuang (in the *Xunzi* and *Aigong* chapter, "Dongye Bi" and "Duke Ding"). Advancing and retreating, he kept to the plumb-line; turning left and right, he traced the compass. Straight as a taut cord, round as a drawn circle. Duke Zhuang thought no patterned weave could surpass this. The Marshal of the Horse said: "No woven braid could exceed this." (This refers to the *Book of Songs* line: "Holding the reins like braided silk.")

He was made to loop a hundred times and return. Cheng [Xuanying] comments: "Letting the horses wheel about like a hook’s curve, a hundred circuits and returns, each retracing its path." Yan He (in the *Aigong* chapter, "Yan Yuan," referring to Duke Ding of Lu) met him and entered to say: "Ji’s horses are about to fail." The Duke remained silent and did not respond. Xuan comments: "Silent, saying nothing." Soon after, the horses indeed failed and returned. The Duke asked: "How did you know?"

He said: "The horses’ strength was exhausted, yet they were still driven—hence I said they would fail." Overtaxing leads to failure; nothing is otherwise. The artisan Chui could spin and surpass the compass and square. Xuan comments: "Surpass, meaning to exceed. With only his hands’ turning, his skill surpassed compass and square—such was his consummate precision." His fingers transformed with things yet did not linger in his mind. Cheng comments: "His hands followed things’ transformations, applying skill according to the object, his mind unburdened by deliberation." Thus his spirit-terrace was whole and unshackled. Xuan comments: "Spirit-terrace: the abode of the spirit. His spirit congealed without the suffering of constraint."

Feet forgotten—shoes’ comfort;
Waist forgotten—belt’s comfort;
Knowledge forgetting right and wrong—mind’s comfort;
No inner change, no outer compliance—events’ meeting’s comfort.
Within, the will unchanged; without, not following things, yet all encounters bring comfort. Beginning with comfort and never not comforted—this is the comfort of forgetting comfort. When one’s nature is inherently comfortable and nowhere is not comfort, this is self-comforting comfort, not comfort dependent on things. Thus even comfort itself is forgotten.

There was a man named Sun Xiu. Cheng comments: "A man of Lu." He came to the gate and addressed Master Bian Qingzi. Li comments: "Bian, the surname; Qingzi, the style." He said: "In my village, I am not called unrefined; in peril, I am not called cowardly. Yet when tilling the fields, I meet no favorable year; when serving a lord, I meet no favorable age. Shunned in my village, driven from my district—what offense have I committed against Heaven? Why must I suffer this fate?"

Sun Xiu exclaimed, "Alas!"—his voice a cry of despair. "I do not understand why I must meet with such a fate!"

Master Bian replied: "Have you alone not heard of the Perfect Person’s way of conduct? They forget their liver and gall, disregard their ears and eyes, let their body crumble, and cast aside their keen hearing and sharp sight. Vast and unbound, they wander beyond the dust and grime of the world—*dust and grime* here meaning the burdens of the vulgar. They roam freely in the work of no-occupation, which is called *acting without reliance, leading without domination*." Xuan comments: "Following one’s nature without relying on ability, guiding things without claiming merit." (This passage is from the *Laozi*.)

"Now you adorn your knowledge to startle the foolish, cultivate your person to expose the filthy, and shine forth as if carrying the sun and moon in your stride—flaunting yourself to stand apart from others. These three lines also appear in the *Shanmu* chapter. You have preserved your whole bodily form, kept all nine orifices intact, and not halfway perished from deafness, blindness, lameness, or deformity to be counted among the ranks of men—this is already good fortune. Why then do you have leisure to complain against Heaven? Be on your way!"

Sun Xiu departed.

Bianzi entered and took his seat. After a while, he looked up to heaven and sighed.

His disciples asked, "Master, why do you sigh?"

Bianzi replied, "Earlier, Sun Xiu came, and I spoke to him of the virtue of the perfected person. I feared he would be startled and thus fall into confusion."

The disciples said, "Not so. If what Sun Xiu said was right and what you said was wrong, then what is wrong cannot confuse what is right. If what Sun Xiu said was wrong and what you said was right, then he was already confused when he came—what fault is that of yours?"

Bianzi said, "Not so. Once, a bird alighted in the outskirts of Lu. The Lord of Lu delighted in it and prepared a grand feast to entertain it, performing the *Nine Shao* to please it. The bird then grew anxious, sorrowful, and dizzy, and dared not eat or drink. This is called *nourishing a bird with what nourishes oneself*. As for *nourishing a bird with what nourishes a bird*, one should let it roost in the deep forest, float upon rivers and lakes, feed it with *weiyis*, and settle it on level ground. (*Weiyi*: Li [Yi] says, 'A great bird that swallows snakes.' Sima [Biao] says, '*Weiyi* means mud.') Yu [Yue] says, 'Those who nourish birds have never been heard to feed them snakes, and "mud" is mere conjecture. In the *Zhile* chapter, it says, *"As for nourishing a bird with what nourishes a bird, one should let it roost in the deep forest, wander on level ground, float upon rivers and lakes, feed it with *shu*, follow its ranks in flight, and dwell in *weiyi*." Thus, this passage should also read, *"feed it with *shu*, dwell in *weiyi*,"* for the transmitted text is missing characters. Moreover, only with *"and dwell"* does the phrase *"then settle it on level ground"* cohere; without these two characters, the following line does not connect.') Now, Sun Xiu is a man of shallow understanding and narrow vision." (Li [Yi] says, "*Kuan* means empty; *qi* means to open. Like an empty opening, his vision is small.) "I spoke to him of the virtue of the perfected person—this is like burdening a mouse with chariots and horses or delighting a sparrow with bells and drums. How could he not be startled?"

Guo [Xiang] says, "This chapter speaks of those who nourish life well: each attains the utmost by following the suitability of their nature." (*Outer Chapters, "Mountain Trees,"* Chapter Twenty)

Su Yu says, "This too is a record by Zhuangzi’s followers, its purport akin to *‘Amid the Human World’*—an art of dwelling in a turbid age and evading harm."

Zhuangzi walked in the mountains and saw a great tree, its branches and leaves lush and flourishing. A woodcutter stood beside it but did not fell it. Asked the reason, he replied, "It is of no use." Zhuangzi said, "This tree, by being useless, has attained its full natural span."

The Master emerged from the mountains—here meaning Zhuangzi—and lodged at the home of an old friend. The friend was pleased and ordered a servant to kill a goose and cook it. The servant asked, "One can caw, the other cannot. Which should we kill?" The host said, "Kill the one that cannot caw."

The next day, a disciple asked Zhuangzi, "Yesterday, the tree in the mountains, by being useless, attained its full natural span. Today, the host’s goose, by being useless, met its death. Where will you, Master, take your stand?"

Zhuangzi laughed and said, "I will take my stand between usefulness and uselessness. Between usefulness and uselessness—it seems akin yet is not. Thus one is not yet free from entanglement. But if one rides the Dao and virtue, floating and wandering, it is not so. With the heart set on the Dao and virtue, there is no need to speak of usefulness or uselessness. Without praise, without blame, now a dragon, now a snake—now manifest, now hidden—one transforms with the times and refuses to cling to any single form. Now rising, now descending, taking harmony as one’s measure, one floats and wanders at the source of the ten thousand things. Treating things as things yet not being treated as a thing by things—how then could one be entangled? This is the method of the Yellow Emperor and Shennong.

As for the passions of the ten thousand things and the transmission of human relations, it is not so. Union leads to separation, completion to destruction. Integrity invites injury, honor provokes censure. Action incurs loss, worth invites schemes, worthlessness invites deceit. How can one be certain of anything? One cannot escape entanglement. How lamentable! Disciples, take heed: it is only in the realm of the Dao and virtue!"

Shi Nan Yiliao visited the Marquis of Lu, who wore a troubled expression. Yiliao asked, "My lord, why do you look so troubled?"

The Marquis of Lu replied, "I study the Way of the former kings and cultivate the undertakings of my predecessors. I revere the spirits and honor the worthy, practicing these without the slightest respite. Yet I cannot escape misfortune—this is why I am troubled."

Yiliao said, "My lord’s method of averting misfortune is shallow indeed. The sleek fox and the patterned leopard dwell in the mountains and forests, crouch in rocky caves—this is stillness. They move by night and rest by day—this is vigilance. Though hungry and thirsty, though hidden and secluded, they still venture cautiously upon the rivers and lakes in search of food—this is steadfastness. Yet they cannot escape the calamity of nets and traps. What is the fault here? It is their fur. Now, my lord’s state—what is its fur? I wish to hear how you govern it."

"Though the beasts remain hidden and secluded, they must still venture far upon the rivers and lakes—where no men tread—to seek their food. This is the steadfastness of their dwelling. Yet even so, they cannot escape the calamity of nets and snares. What fault is theirs? It is their fur that brings them disaster. Now, is not the state of Lu your fur? I wish for you to flay your form and shed your skin, to cleanse your heart and cast off desire, and wander in the wilds where no man dwells.

In the southern lands of Yue, there is a city called the Land of Establishing Virtue. Its people are simple and unadorned, with little selfishness and few desires. They know how to act but not how to hoard; they give without seeking repayment. They do not know where duty leads, nor where propriety guides. Unrestrained and untethered, they tread the great Dao. Their lives are joyful, and their deaths are fitting to bury. As Guo Xiang says, 'One may dwell there from beginning to end.'

I wish for you to abandon your state, forsake custom, and walk in harmony with the Dao."

The lord said, "That Way is distant and perilous, with rivers and mountains in between. I have no boat or carriage—what am I to do?"

Yiliao said, "Be without arrogance in your form, without attachment to your dwelling—let this be your boat and carriage."

The lord said, "That Way is secluded and remote, with no people. With whom shall I be neighbors? I have no provisions, no food—how can I reach it?"

Yiliao said, "Lessen your expenditures, reduce your desires. Though you have no provisions, you will find sufficiency. As Guo Xiang says, 'To know contentment is to lack nothing.' Cross the rivers and float upon the sea. Gaze upon it, and you will not see its shores. The farther you go, the less you will know its end. As Xuan says, 'To journey alone is to delve so deeply.' Those who see you off will turn back from the shore, for none can follow. From this point, you will be far indeed. As Guo Xiang says, 'You will stand transcendent and independent above the myriad things.'

Thus, to possess others is a burden; to be possessed by others is a worry. Yao did not possess others, nor was he possessed by them. I wish to rid you of your burdens, remove your worries, and wander alone with the Dao in the Land of Vastness.

When a double-hulled boat crosses the river, and an empty boat drifts into it, even a hot-tempered man would not grow angry. But if there were a man aboard, one would shout to open or close the way. If the first shout goes unheard, a second follows, and if that too is ignored, a third shout is sure to be accompanied by curses. Before, there was no anger—now there is. Before, the boat was empty—now it is full. This is the reason.

If a man can empty himself and wander through the world, who could harm him?"

---

Beigong She, a minister of Duke Ling of Wei, collected funds to cast bells. He built an altar outside the city gates and completed the upper and lower chimes in three months. As Xuan says, "The time was short, yet the funds gathered were vast."

Prince Qingji saw this and asked, "What method did you employ?"

She said, "In the space between, I dared not devise any method. My heart was set solely on the casting of the bells. I have heard: 'After carving and polishing, return to simplicity.' In this age of over-refinement, only sincerity can move people. I appeared artless and without knowledge, hoping only for success. I seemed hesitant and uncertain, fearing a lack of sincerity. I gathered and wandered, sending off the departing and welcoming the arriving. Those who came were not forbidden; those who left were not stopped. I indulged the strong-willed and followed those who clung to me, allowing them to exhaust themselves. Thus, from dawn to dusk, I gathered funds without the slightest harm—how much more so for those who tread the great thoroughfare of the Dao!"

Confucius was besieged between Chen and Cai, going seven days without cooked food. Taigong Ren went to console him. As Li says, "Taigong is a title of nobility; Ren is his name." As Yu says, "The *Guangyun* records a surname Taigong, as in *Shiben*'s Taigong Ying Shu. Thus, Taigong is a compound surname, not merely a noble title."

Confucius said, "Were you nearly dead?"
He replied, "Yes."
"Did you detest death?"
He replied, "Yes."

Taigong Ren said, "I have once spoken of the Way of not dying. In the Eastern Sea there is a bird, whose name is *Yidai*. As a bird, it is slow and sluggish, appearing incapable. It takes flight only with support and roosts only under pressure, never daring to be first in advancing or last in retreating. It does not dare to be the first to eat, but must take the leftovers. Thus, it is never expelled from the flock, and others never harm it, so it avoids disaster. The straight tree is the first felled; the sweet well is the first drained. As Guo says, 'This is the peril of talent.' You, with your intentions, adorn knowledge to startle the foolish, cultivate yourself to expose filth, and shine forth as if bearing the sun and moon in your stride—these three phrases already appear in the *Da Sheng* chapter. Thus, you cannot escape harm. Long ago, I heard the Perfect One of Great Accomplishment say—Cheng says, 'The Perfect One of Great Accomplishment is Laozi'—'He who boasts achieves no merit; he who vaunts his success collapses; he who vaunts his fame suffers loss.' As Guo says, 'Those who rely on merit and fame as their accomplishment have never preserved wholeness.' Who can relinquish merit and fame and return to the common people? The Dao flows without dwelling in prominence; the Dao spreads throughout the world yet does not conspicuously occupy it. Virtue is enacted without claiming a place. Pure and constant, it is nearly akin to madness. Erasing traces and relinquishing power, it does not pursue merit or fame. Thus, it incurs no blame from others, and others incur no blame from it. The Perfected One remains unknown—this saying appears in the *Qiu Shui* chapter, where 'Perfected' is written as 'Dao.' Why then do you delight so greatly?"

Confucius exclaimed, "Excellent!" He then declined his social circles, dismissed his disciples, and fled to the great marshes. Clad in coarse furs and hemp, he ate acorns and chestnuts, forsaking fine garments and delicacies. Entering among beasts, he did not disrupt their herds; entering among birds, he did not disturb their flocks. Birds and beasts felt no aversion toward him—how much less would men!

Confucius asked Zisang Hu, "I have twice been banished from Lu, had my tree felled in Song, erased my traces in Wei, suffered destitution in Shang and Zhou, and been besieged between Chen and Cai. Having endured these many hardships, my close associates have grown more distant, and my followers have scattered further. Why is this?"

Zisang Hu replied, "Have you not heard of the man from Jia who fled? Lin Hui abandoned a jade disc worth a thousand gold pieces and fled carrying his infant son. Someone asked, 'Was it for the sake of wealth? The child’s possessions are meager. Was it for the sake of burden? The child’s encumbrance is great. Why abandon a jade disc worth a thousand gold pieces to carry a child and flee?' Lin Hui said, 'That was bound by profit; this is joined by Heaven.’ Those bound by profit abandon one another when pressed by poverty, misfortune, or harm; those joined by Heaven gather together when pressed by the same. The distance between gathering and abandoning is vast. Moreover, the friendship of the noble is as bland as water, while the friendship of the petty is as sweet as sweet wine. The noble grow closer in their blandness, while the petty grow estranged in their sweetness. Those who unite without cause will part without cause."

Confucius said, "I respectfully receive your instruction." He then walked slowly, meandering as if in flight, returned home, abandoned his studies, and discarded his books. His disciples no longer deferred to him as before, yet their affection for him deepened all the more, for true sincerity had moved them.

One day, Sang Hu spoke again: "When Shun was about to die, he earnestly instructed Yu, saying, 'Be ever vigilant! In form, nothing is better than following; in feeling, nothing is better than sincerity. Following ensures no separation; sincerity ensures no exhaustion. Without separation or exhaustion, one does not seek adornment to match form; without seeking adornment to match form, one does not depend on external things.'"

Zhuangzi wore coarse cloth patched with hemp, tied his shoes with straightened hemp strands, and passed by King Hui of Wei. The king said, "Why is the Master so destitute?"

Zhuangzi replied, "This is poverty, not destitution. When a scholar possesses the Dao and virtue but cannot put them into practice, that is destitution. Wearing tattered clothes and worn-out shoes—this is poverty, not destitution. This is what is called not encountering the right time. Have you alone not seen the leaping monkeys?"

When they secure nan, zi, yu, and zhang trees, grasping their vines and branches, they lord it among them—lording it, as if to say, swelling with self-importance. Even Yi or Peng Meng could not cast a sidelong glance at them. Yet when they find themselves among zhe, ji, zhi, and gou trees, they tread warily, glance sideways, tremble and quake. This is not that their sinews and bones have suddenly stiffened or lost their suppleness; it is that their circumstances are unfavorable, and they cannot fully display their abilities. Now, to dwell among benighted rulers and chaotic ministers and yet wish to avoid destitution—how could this be possible? This is like Bi Gan, whose heart was carved out—a clear portent indeed!"

Confucius was stranded between Chen and Cai, going seven days without cooked food. Leaning left against a withered tree, striking its withered branches with his right hand, he sang the airs of the Biao clan—Biao being the same as Yan, as mentioned in the "Heavenly Revolutions" chapter. He had the instruments but not the rhythm, for though he struck the branches against the wood, there was no measure to it.

The song had its sound but lacked the proper notes of gong and jue—so Xuanzai notes: "There was singing but no musical harmony." Yet the sound of the wood and the human voice together struck the heart with a plow-like clarity—so Xuanzai explains: "Like the loosening of soil beneath the plow, it brought release." Yan Hui faced forward with folded hands, then turned his gaze to observe. Turning his gaze means looking back.

Fearing that Hui might grow complacent and reach grandiosity, or grow attached to himself and descend into sorrow—for "reaching" means arriving—Confucius said: "Hui! To not suffer the diminishment of Heaven is easy, as Guo notes: 'Only by accepting it does it become easy.' To not accept the benefits of others is difficult, as Cheng explains: 'What comes by chance and is entrusted to one—pushing it away is hard.' There is no beginning that is not also an end, as Guo observes: 'What is a beginning today was an end yesterday, so what we call a beginning is itself an end—speaking of the endlessness of transformation.' Man and Heaven are one, as Guo says: 'Both are natural.' Who, then, is the singer now?" Guo comments: "Let it be as it is; the singer is not I."

Hui asked: "May I ask why not suffering the diminishment of Heaven is easy?"

Confucius replied: "Hunger and thirst, cold and heat, confinement and immobility—these are the workings of Heaven and Earth, the unfolding of the movement of things, as Sima explains: 'Unfolding means manifesting.' To speak of going along with them is to say: as Xuanzai notes, 'Only by following the flow and going with them.' As a minister, one does not dare to flee them, as Xuanzai says: 'A minister, having received the ruler’s mandate, cannot in principle escape.' If even the Way of the minister is thus, how much more so the Way of awaiting Heaven!" To await Heaven with acceptance is to render diminishment powerless to diminish—hence it is easy.

Hui asked: "What do you mean by not accepting the benefits of others being difficult?"

Confucius replied: "At first, one’s path opens in all four directions, as Xuanzai notes: 'At first, when one advances, all four directions open without hindrance.' Rank and emolument arrive without end—such is the benefit from others. Yet what is advantageous to things is not one’s own, as Xuanzai says: 'This advantage to things has nothing to do with one’s inherent nature.' My fate lies beyond myself, as Xuanzai explains: 'This is merely the occasional alignment of my qi and destiny with external circumstances.' The noble man does not steal; the worthy man does not pilfer. If I were to take these, what would I be doing? As Xuanzai says: 'To accept rank and emolument emptily is no different from theft—something your kind of worthy man would not do. Why, then, would I take them?' Thus it is said: No bird is wiser than the swallow. As the *Shiwen* notes: 'Wise here means knowing; some say the swallow is the swallow.' Where its eyes should not linger, it does not tarry in looking—when it sees where it should not be, it departs at once, not waiting to circle back. Even if its food falls, it abandons it and flies away. If it drops what it carries, it does not retrieve it. Though it fears humans, it nests among them, as Cheng explains: 'Nests means enters.' Yet it does so only because its refuge lies there—no different from a state’s altars of soil and grain, which people cannot abandon. When a noble man dwells in another’s state, he too must recognize that the altars of soil and grain are present and devote himself to his duties. As for the benefits of rank and emolument, they do not augment my nature. One should remember: 'Do not enter a state in peril; do not dwell in a state in chaos.' But those who understand this are few—hence it is difficult."

Hui asked: "What do you mean by there being no beginning that is not also an end?"

Confucius replied: "The transformation of the myriad things proceeds without our knowing who succeeds it—Heaven transforms and gives life to the myriad things, renewing them endlessly, yet we do not know who takes over in succession. How can we know its end? How can we know its beginning?"

Thus, there is no beginning that is not also an end—one must simply uphold correctness and await it. To maintain rectitude and await it is all.

Hui asked: "What do you mean by Heaven and man being one?"

Confucius replied: "When there is man, it is Heaven; when there is Heaven, it is also Heaven. As Xuan comments: 'Man and Heaven are both brought forth by Heaven. Heaven is the principle.' That man cannot possess Heaven is his nature. As Xuan comments: 'Man may fail to fully possess his Heaven, for his nature is subject to augmentation or diminution.' The sage, at ease, embodies the ceaseless passage of time and thus fulfills his end."

As Zhuang Zhou wandered near the fence of Diaoling, Sima Biao notes: "Diaoling is the name of a mound; *fan* means fence. He saw an unusual magpie coming from the south, its wings spanning seven feet, its eyes measuring a full inch in diameter. Wang Niansun observes: '*Yun* stands in parallel with *guang*; *guang* refers to breadth, so *yun* refers to length. *Mu da yun cun* means 'its eyes were a full inch in diameter.' In the *Yue Yu*, it says, '*Guang yun* a hundred *li*,' and Wei’s commentary explains: 'East-west is *guang*; south-north is *yun*.' Thus, *yun* refers to length." The magpie brushed against Zhuang Zhou’s forehead and alighted in the chestnut grove. Cheng comments: "*Gan* means to strike." Zhuang Zhou said: "What bird is this? Its wings are vast yet it does not fly away; its eyes are large yet it does not see." He hitched up his robe, strode quickly forward, took up his sling, and waited for an opportunity. Sima comments: "*Jue* means to move swiftly; *liu* means to watch for an opening." He saw a cicada enjoying the fine shade and forgetting its own body; a mantis, seizing the cover of a leaf, pounced upon it, using the leaf as a shield as if to grasp it. Seeing its gain, it forgot its form; forgetting its form, it was seen by the magpie. The unusual magpie, drawn by the prospect of profit, saw the advantage and forgot its true nature. As Xuan comments: "Having lost its true nature, it neither flew away nor saw." Zhuang Zhou, startled, said: "Alas! Things are indeed mutually entangled. Guo comments: 'Those who seek profit from one another are always mutually burdened.' The two kinds summoned one another." As Xuan comments: "The cicada summoned the mantis; the mantis summoned the magpie—each brought harm upon itself." He cast aside his sling and turned to flee, but the warden of the grove pursued and berated him. Cheng comments: "The *yuren* is the keeper of the chestnut grove. Suspecting him of stealing chestnuts, he chased and questioned him." Zhuang Zhou returned and for three days did not emerge.
