The *Shiwen* notes: "'Three days' appears as 'three months' in one version. Sima comments: 'He did not go out, remaining seated in the courtyard for three months.'" Wang Niansun observes: "The following passage speaks of 'a short while,' so 'three days' is correct. If following Sima’s interpretation, 'courtyard' should include the word 'out' for clarity. The phrase 'very much not *ting*' below, if taken to mean 'very much did not go out to the courtyard,' is even more unnatural. *Ting* should be read as *cheng*. *Bu cheng* means 'not at ease'; *shen bu cheng* means 'very much not at ease.' The word *cheng* was anciently pronounced like *cheng*, its sound close to *ting*, hence it was interchangeable with *ting*."

Lin Ju followed and asked him, Sima comments: "A disciple of Zhuangzi." "Master, why have you been so disquieted of late?"

Zhuang Zhou replied: "I guarded form and forgot my own body, preserved the appearance of things and forgot myself. I observed the turbid waters and was deluded by the clear abyss. I knew that creatures pursue profit but did not realize that I should avoid suspicion. Moreover, I have heard from the Master—Laozi, as Cheng comments—that one should 'enter their customs and follow their ways.' Today, I wandered in Diaoling and forgot myself, becoming like the cicada. The unusual magpie struck my forehead; I wandered in the chestnut grove and forgot my true nature, becoming like the magpie. The warden of the chestnut grove took me for a thief and humiliated me—this is why I have been disquieted."

Yang Zhu traveled to Song. Sima comments: "Yang Zhu." (Note: According to the *Yu Yan* chapter of the *Liezi*.) He lodged at an inn. The innkeeper had two concubines—one beautiful, the other plain. The plain one was honored, while the beautiful one was slighted. Yang Zhu asked the reason, and the innkeeper’s young servant replied: "The beautiful one is beautiful in her own eyes, and her beauty makes her proud. I do not see her as beautiful. The plain one is plain in her own eyes, and her plainness makes her humble. I do not see her as plain."

Yang Zhu said: "Disciples, take note! Act with virtue but discard the conduct of self-regard. Wherever you go, will you not be loved?"

---

**Chapter 21: Tian Zifang (Outer Chapters)**

Tian Zifang sat in attendance upon Marquis Wen of Wei and repeatedly praised Xi Gong. Li comments: "Tian Zifang was Marquis Wen’s teacher, named Wuze. Xi Gong was a worthy man." (Sima’s text reads *Ji* instead of *Xi*.) The Marquis said: "Is Xi Gong your teacher?"

Zifang replied: "No. He is a villager of Wuze. His words on the Dao often accord with reason, so Wuze praises him."

The Marquis said: "Then you have no teacher?"

Zifang said: "I do."

The Marquis asked: "Who is your teacher?"

Zifang said: "Dongguo Shunzi."

The Marquis said: "Then why have you never praised him?"

Zifang said: "His character is true—outwardly human, inwardly empty of Heaven. Yu comments: "The *Huainanzi*’s *Shu Zhen Xun* says, 'An empty chamber gives rise to brightness.' The commentary reads: 'Empty refers to the heart.' Here, it means outwardly human, inwardly the heart of Heaven. Ancient texts read *xu* as belonging to the lower line, which is incorrect." He follows along while preserving the true, is clear yet accommodates things, clear yet not harsh. When others lack the Dao, he responds with upright bearing to awaken them, dissolving their intentions. Guo comments: "Vast and clear, empty and upright—he merely rectifies himself, and the perversity of others naturally fades." How could Wuze be worthy of praising him!"

Zifang departed. The Marquis, lost in thought, remained silent for the rest of the day. Guo comments: "*Tangran* describes his self-forgetfulness." He summoned the ministers who had stood before him—those who had attended and heard the exchange—and said to them: "How distant is the gentleman of perfect virtue! At first, I believed the words of sagely wisdom and the conduct of benevolence and righteousness to be the ultimate. After hearing of Zifang’s teacher, my form dissolved and I had no desire to move; my mouth was sealed and I had no desire to speak. What I have learned is but a clod of earth—Xuan comments: "It is merely the coarsest of things. Outside of Heaven’s truth, all is but a clod." Truly, Wei has been a burden to me!" Guo comments: "Those who know what is most noble consider human rank a burden."

---

When Wenbo Xuezi traveled to Qi, Guo comments: "His surname was Wen, given name Bo, style Xuezi—a man of Chu who embodied the Dao," he lodged in Lu. A man of Lu requested an audience with him. Wenbo Xuezi said: "I cannot. I have heard that the gentlemen of the Central States are enlightened in ritual and righteousness but clumsy in understanding the human heart. Xuan comments: "Skilled in superficial learning but ignorant of the fundamental nature." I do not wish to meet him."

When he returned to Qi and again lodged in Lu, the same man requested an audience. Wenbo Xuezi said: "Before, he sought to see me; now, he seeks to see me again. He must have something to rouse me." He went out to meet the guest and, upon returning, sighed. The next day, he met the guest again and returned sighing. His servant said: "Every time you meet this guest, you return and sigh. Why?"

He replied: "I told you before: 'The people of the Central States are enlightened in ritual and righteousness but clumsy in understanding the human heart.' The one who saw me earlier advanced and retreated in fixed circles, in fixed squares; moved with ease like a dragon, like a tiger. Guo comments: "His bowing and yielding, advancing and retreating, were as measured as compass and square, as sinuous as a dragon, as powerful as a tiger." He admonished me as a son admonishes a father, guided me as a father guides a son. That is why I sighed." Guo comments: "He corrected me as a son serves a father, instructed me as a father teaches a son. Though there is affection between near and distant, high and low, it is not of Heaven’s nature—why the earnestness? Knowing the harm of sagely traces, I see such pretense, and so I sigh."

Confucius met him and said nothing. Zilu said: "Master, you have long wished to see Wenbo Xuezi. Now that you have met him, you say nothing. Why?"

Confucius said: "For such a man, the Dao is present the moment one’s gaze falls upon him. There is no room for words." Xuan comments: "The moment one’s eyes meet him, the Dao is already there. What need is there for words?"

---

Yan Yuan asked Confucius: "When the Master walks, I walk; when the Master hurries, I hurry; when the Master runs, I run; when the Master races ahead, leaving all behind, I can only stare blankly after him."

The Master said: "Hui, what do you mean?"

Yan Yuan said: "When the Master walks, I walk; when the Master speaks, I speak; when the Master hurries, I hurry; when the Master debates, I debate; when the Master runs, I run; when the Master speaks of the Dao, I too speak of the Dao."

Though when you race ahead, leaving all behind, I can only stare blankly after you—this is because, though you speak not, you are trusted; though you draw no close comparisons, your care is all-encompassing; though you bear no regalia, the people gather before you like a swelling tide, yet they know not why it is so.

Confucius said: "Alas! Should we not examine this? No sorrow is greater than the death of the heart, and the death of the body is secondary to it. The sun rises in the east and sets in the westernmost reaches—all things align themselves accordingly. Those with eyes and feet depend on it to accomplish their tasks, awaiting the day to begin their work. When it emerges, the world stirs; when it withdraws, the world rests. All things are thus: their death and life alike depend on this rhythm. Once I received my completed form, I did not resist change while awaiting its end, but moved in imitation of things, as things moved and I moved with them. Day and night flow without pause, and I know not where they conclude. My form took shape of its own accord, yet even knowing fate, I could not foresee what lay before me. Thus I pass my days. My whole life, I have grasped your arm only to lose it—can this not be lamented? You cling to what I once clung to. What I once clung to has already vanished, yet you seek it as if it still exists. This is like searching for a horse in an empty marketplace. What I hold in my thoughts of you fades swiftly, and what you hold in your thoughts of me fades just as swiftly. Yet why should you worry? Though the old me is forgotten, there is always something unforgotten remaining."

Confucius visited Lao Dan, who had just washed his hair and was letting it dry with his hair loose, motionless as though not human. The *Shiwen* notes: "慹, pronounced *niè* or *dì lì*; Sima Biao says: 'the appearance of stillness.'" Guo Xiang comments: "the utmost in tranquility and quiescence." Confucius withdrew and waited. After a while, he approached and said: "Was I deluded just now? Or was it truly so? A moment ago, Master, your form stood stiff as withered wood, as though you had left things and parted from men, standing alone in solitude."

Lao Dan said: "I let my mind wander in the beginning of things." Xuan Ying comments: "The beginning of things is the time before things existed. To let the mind wander in the time before things existed is to encounter the true Dao."

Confucius said: "What do you mean?"

Lao Dan replied: "The mind is confounded and cannot know; the mouth is shut and cannot speak. I have tried to discuss it for you in tentative terms. The utmost yin is solemn and still; the utmost yang is blazing and bright. Solemn stillness arises from heaven; blazing brightness issues from earth. The two intermingle and harmonize, and things are born. Some become their guiding principles, yet no one sees their form. Who binds them together as a net? They ebb and flow, filling emptiness; now dark, now bright; changing day by day, transforming month by month. Each day they act, yet no one sees their work. As Cheng Xuanying says: 'Yin wanes and yang waxes; summer is full and winter empty; night is dark and day is bright. Day by day they change, month by month they transform, ever renewing. Thus each day they act.' Life has its place of sprouting; death has its place of return. Beginning and end revolve without end, yet no one knows where they conclude. As Guo Xiang says: 'What is called meeting it, one does not see its head; following it, one does not see its rear.' Su Yu comments: 'The two lines about "beginning and end" are what is meant by "just as it is born it dies, just as it dies it is born."' But this is not so. Then what is their master?"

Cheng Xuanying comments: "If not for this all-pervading, life-generating Dao, who would be the master of the myriad things?"

Confucius said: "May I ask about wandering in this?"

Cheng Xuanying comments: "May I ask how to let the mind wander in this Dao? What power does one gain from wandering in it?"

Lao Dan said: "To attain this is the utmost beauty and the utmost joy. To attain the utmost beauty and wander in the utmost joy is called the perfected person."

Confucius said: "I wish to hear the method."

Lao Dan replied: "Grass-eating beasts do not dislike changing marshes; water-dwelling creatures do not dislike changing waters. They undergo small changes but do not lose their great constancy. As Cheng Xuanying says: 'Dislike means to find troublesome. Change means to move. Beasts that eat grass do not find it troublesome to move from one marsh to another; creatures that live in water do not find it troublesome to move from one body of water to another. As long as there is grass or water, they do not lose their great constancy. Whether they go east or west is but a small change. It is like a person abiding in the great Dao, following change and responding to transformation, never ceasing to be oneself. This is not losing the great constancy. The changes of life and death are but small matters.' Joy and anger, sorrow and happiness do not enter the breast. Li Yi comments: 'Breast means within.' Guo Xiang comments: 'Knowing that small changes do not lose the great constancy.' The world is where the myriad things are one. As Xuan Ying says: 'The myriad transformations do not exceed the true master.' To attain this oneness and be the same as it, then the four limbs and the hundred parts of the body will be but dust and dirt, and life and death, beginning and end will be but day and night, and nothing can disturb them. How much less the boundaries of gain and loss, fortune and misfortune! As Xuan Ying says: 'Boundaries means junctures.' To discard what is subordinate is like discarding mud and mire, for one knows that the body is nobler than what is subordinate. Subordinate means what is attached, such as officials and retainers. What is noble lies in me and is not lost in change. One does not lose one’s nobility through change. Moreover, the myriad transformations have no end, and I too join in their endlessness. What is there to trouble the mind? As Xuan Ying says: 'Then one wanders in freedom.' Only those who have attained the Dao understand this."

Confucius departed and told Yan Hui: "In my relation to the Dao, am I not like a gnat in vinegar? Guo Xiang comments: 'A gnat in vinegar is a tiny midge in a jar.' Had the Master not lifted the covering from my eyes, I would not have known the great wholeness of heaven and earth. As Xuan Ying says: 'The great wholeness of heaven and earth is where the myriad things are one.'" Zhuangzi visited Duke Ai of Lu.

"Cheng comments: 'Zhuangzi was contemporary with King Hui of Wei and King Wei of Qi, one hundred twenty years after Duke Ai of Lu. That this says "visited Duke Ai of Lu" is but a parable.' Duke Ai said: 'Lu has many Ru scholars, yet few who follow the Master’s method.' Cheng comments: 'Method means art.' He meant that the land of Lu seldom practiced Zhuangzi’s art of non-action.

Zhuangzi said: 'Lu has few Ru.'

Duke Ai said: 'The whole state of Lu wears the Ru garb—how can you say there are few?'

Zhuangzi said: 'I have heard that the Ru wear round caps, knowing the seasons of heaven; square shoes, knowing the forms of earth. Li comments: 'Square means rectangular.' Those who tie jade jue to their sashes decide matters when they arise. Cheng comments: 'The tie is a five-colored silk cord, threading the jade jue to adorn the sash. Jue means to decide.' A noble person who possesses the Way may not wear its garb; one who wears its garb may not know the Way. If Your Grace truly does not believe this, why not issue a decree throughout the state: "Whoever wears this garb without possessing its Way shall be put to death"?'

Thereupon, Duke Ai proclaimed this for five days, and no one in Lu dared wear the Ru garb. Only one man, clad in Ru attire, stood at the palace gate. The Duke summoned him and questioned him on state affairs; though the questions turned a thousand ways, his answers were inexhaustible.

Zhuangzi said: 'With the whole state of Lu, there is but one Ru—can this be called many?'

Baili Xi’s rank and salary did not enter his heart, so when he fed cattle, the cattle grew fat. This made Duke Mu of Qin forget his lowly status and entrust him with governance. The Lord of Yu’s life and death did not enter his heart—whether repairing the granary or dredging the well, it was all the same. Thus he was able to move others."

At Chengyi and Chengdu, the masters bestowed the imperial abdication.

Duke Yuan of Song was about to commission paintings. All the court scribes arrived, receiving the steward’s words: "You have been summoned." They bowed and stood in place; licking their brushes and mixing ink, half remained outside. It was said: "These cannot paint."

One scribe arrived late, strolling leisurely without haste, his voice unhurried. He received the bow but did not stand, then went to his quarters. The Duke sent someone to observe him, who saw him strip off his robes and sit cross-legged, bare. The steward said: "He is about to paint, so he removes his clothes to reveal his form."

The Duke said: "This is the true painter." Guo [Xiang] said: "One whose inner power is sufficient is at ease in spirit and settled in mind."

King Wen observed at Zang, where he saw a man fishing—but his fishing was no fishing, for he cast no bait, his mind not on the fish. He did not hold his rod as if fishing, nor treat fishing as his task. He was one who fished, yet fished for something else. This was his constant way of fishing, not an occasional act.

King Wen wished to raise the man and entrust him with governance, yet feared his ministers, elders, and kinsmen would not be at ease. He hesitated, considering whether to abandon the idea, but could not bear the thought of the people being left without a protector. Thus, at dawn, he gathered his ministers and said:

"Last night, I dreamed of a nobleman, dark of complexion and with a full beard, riding a dappled horse with one red hoof. He commanded: *'Entrust governance to the Elder of Zang, and the people may yet find relief!*'"

The assembled ministers started and said, "It is the late lord, our former king!"—referring to King Ji Li.

Yu Yue notes: *"Below 'former king' a character for 'command' is missing, as evidenced by the later phrase 'the command of the former king.'"*

King Wen said, "Then let us divine by oracle."

The ministers replied, "The command of the former king leaves no room for doubt—what need is there for further hesitation? Why resort to divination?"

Thereupon, they welcomed the Elder of Zang and entrusted him with governance. The established rituals remained unchanged; no partial decrees were issued. For three years, King Wen surveyed the realm and found that the ranked officers had dismantled their factions and dispersed their cliques, no longer forming partisan alliances.

Yu Yue notes: *"In the *Zuozhuan* (Duke Xuan, Year 2), 'Hua Yuan became the *zhi*,' with Du Yu’s commentary: '*Zhi* means the leading master.' Officers must first have a leader before they gather followers, so to scatter their cliques, one must first dismantle their *zhi*."*

The high officials no longer cultivated virtue in isolation but converged in shared goodness. Measures and bushels dared not vary at the four borders.

The *Shiwen* glosses: *"斔 is read as *yu*. Li [Yi] says: 'Six *hu* and four *dou* make one *yu*.'"*
*Note: This implies that measures from other regions, fearing discrepancies in size or form, did not circulate within the borders.*

When the ranked officers dismantled their factions and dispersed their cliques, unity prevailed. When high officials no longer cultivated virtue in isolation, their efforts aligned. When measures and bushels dared not vary at the four borders, the feudal lords harbored no divided loyalties.

Thereupon, King Wen appointed the Elder of Zang as Grand Preceptor. Facing north, he inquired, "Can governance extend to all under Heaven?"

The Elder of Zang remained silent and unresponsive, then vaguely declined. By morning, he had issued orders, and by night, he had fled—vanishing without a trace for the rest of his life.

Yan Yuan asked Confucius, "Was King Wen perhaps still lacking in virtue?"—implying, *"Was his power insufficient to win trust?"*—"Then why resort to dreams?"

Confucius replied, "Silence! Say no more!"

King Wen had already perfected it—Guo Xiang notes, *"He entrusted all matters to his ministers without claiming authority himself; this was perfection."*—so why speak of fault-finding? He merely followed the fleeting moment—Cheng Xuanying explains, *"Xun (循) means to follow; si xu (斯须) means a brief instant."* Guo Xiang adds, *"The si xu is the moment when the people’s sentiments hover between awakening and unawareness. King Wen followed and seized it to harmonize with the multitude’s will."*

Lie Yukou shot for Bohun Wuren—*Liezi: The Yellow Emperor* writes *"Wumou"* instead of *"Wuren."* He drew the bow to its fullest, Sima Biao notes, *"Guan refers to the arrowhead."* (Zhang Zhan’s commentary: *"The string fully drawn, the arrowhead at its limit."*) He placed a cup of water on his elbow—Guo Xiang explains, *"His left hand was as steady as a rock, his right as supple as a branch. When the right hand loosed the arrow, the left remained unaware, so the cup of water could rest there."* When he released the arrow, the next arrow immediately followed—Cheng Xuanying says, *"Ta (沓) means repeated."* (The *Yellow Emperor* chapter writes *"di"* (镝) instead of *"shi"* (适), though the meaning is the same.) The moment one arrow was loosed, the next was already nocked. Even as the arrows flew, the cup remained balanced on his elbow. At that moment, he was like a wooden figure—motionless, like a carved effigy.

Bohun Wuren said, *"This is the shooting of one who shoots, not the shooting of one who does not shoot."* Zhang’s commentary: *"Though he has mastered the principles of shooting, he cannot avoid being attached to the act. The shooting of one who does not shoot is to forget both skill and failure—though he does not shoot, he is one with shooting."* *"Let me try climbing a high mountain with you, treading on perilous rocks, standing at the edge of an abyss a hundred ren deep—could you shoot then?"* Could you shoot with the shooting of one who does not shoot?

Thereupon, Wuren ascended the high mountain, trod on the perilous rocks, and stood at the edge of the abyss. Turning his back, he retreated step by step, two-tenths of his feet hanging over the void—Cheng Xuanying explains, *"He stepped backward toward the abyss, with two-tenths of his feet suspended in empty space."* He beckoned Lie Yukou forward. Lie Yukou prostrated himself on the ground, sweat streaming down to his heels.

Bohun Wuren said, *"The perfected person gazes upward into the blue sky, plunges downward into the Yellow Springs—Guo Qingfan notes, *"Qian (潜) here pairs with kui (窥) and should be glossed as ce (测), 'to fathom.' The *Erya* states, 'Qian means ce.'"*—commands the eight directions, yet his spirit and qi remain unchanged."* Guo Xiang explains, *"Huichi (挥斥) means to act freely."* *"Now you tremble with fear, your eyes darting in alarm—*"Shiwen"* cites the *Erya*: *'Xun (恂) means trembling.' Li Yi writes it as xuan (眴), pronounced xun.'"* Zhang’s commentary cites He Chengtian’s *Zuan*: *"The people of Wu call blinking xun (眴) mu (目)."* That is, his heart was afraid, and his eyes grew dizzy. *"In this, you are in grave danger!"* Guo Xiang notes, *"With fear, one loses much."*

Jian Wu asked Sunshu Ao, *"You served as Prime Minister three times yet showed no elation, and resigned three times without sorrow. At first, I doubted you, but now I see the joyful ease in the space between your brows—how do you cultivate your heart?"*

Sunshu Ao replied, *"How do I surpass others? I accept that its coming cannot be refused and its going cannot be stopped. I regard gain and loss as no concern of mine, and so I show no sorrow. How do I surpass others? Moreover, I do not know whether it lies in the office or in myself—"*Xuan’s commentary: *"He does not know whether the honor lies in the Prime Minister’s post or in himself."* *"If it lies in the office, it is not mine;"* Xuan’s commentary: *"If it lies in the office, it has nothing to do with me."* *"if it lies in me, it is not in the office."* Xuan’s commentary: *"If it lies in me, it has nothing to do with the office."* *"I am about to hesitate, about to look around in all directions—"*The *Nourishing the Lord of Life* chapter also says, *"He looks around in all directions, his heart brimming with satisfaction."*—*"how could I have time to consider whether others value or despise me?"*

Confucius heard of this and said, *"The true persons of old could not be swayed by the clever, seduced by the beautiful, robbed by thieves, or befriended by Fuxi or the Yellow Emperor—"*Cheng Xuanying explains, *"The wise could not debate them, the beautiful could not corrupt them, bandits could not plunder them, and even the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors could not claim their friendship!"*—*"Life and death are great matters, yet they caused no change in themselves—how much less titles and emoluments! Such persons could traverse the great mountains without obstruction—"*Cheng Xuanying says, *"Jie (介) means hindrance."*—*"plunge into deep springs without getting wet, dwell in lowliness and obscurity without distress—"*Xuan’s commentary: *"Poverty and humble status could not afflict them."*—*"their spirit filled Heaven and Earth. Having given all to others, they themselves possessed even more."* Their numinous power filled Heaven and Earth; though they bestowed it all to aid others, they themselves gained still more.

The King of Chu sat with the Lord of Fan. After a short while, the King’s attendants announced three times, *"Fan is no more."*—*"Shiwen"* cites Sima Biao: *"Fan was a state in Gong County of Ji Commandery."* The *Zuozhuan* records, *"Fan was a descendant of the Duke of Zhou."* In the seventh year of Duke Yin, there is mention of the Earl of Fan. Cheng Xuanying’s note—*"King Wen of Chu sat with the Marquis of Fan Xi"*—has no known source. Guo Xiang explains, *"This indicates three omens of collapse."* Yu Yue counters, *"The King of Chu’s attendants spoke of three people from Fan who had perished. Guo’s commentary is incorrect."*

The Lord of Fan said, *"Fan’s demise does not diminish my existence. If Fan’s demise does not diminish my existence, then Chu’s existence does not ensure its own survival. From this perspective, Fan has never truly perished, and Chu has never truly existed."*

From *Zhuangzi Jijie*, Scroll Six, Outer Chapters: "Knowledge Wanders North" (Chapter Twenty-Two):

Knowledge wandered north to the Dark Waters, ascended the Hidden Mound of Fen,—*Shiwen* notes: *"Fen* is pronounced *fēn*. Li [Yi] says: 'The mound rises hidden and uneven.'"—and there encountered Non-Action’s Discourse.

Cheng [Xuanying] comments: "This chapter employs fictive names to convey its meaning through allegory."

Knowledge addressed Non-Action’s Discourse: "I wish to ask you something: How should one think and reflect to grasp the Dao? How should one dwell and conduct oneself to rest in the Dao?—*dwelling and conduct* referring to one’s abode and practice. By what means and through what path may one attain the Dao?"—*means* signifying *to follow*, *path* signifying *to proceed by*.

Three questions went unanswered by Non-Action’s Discourse—not because he refused to answer, but because he did not know how.

Xuan [Ying] remarks: "The Dao is fundamentally beyond words."

Failing to receive an answer, Knowledge returned south of the White Waters, ascended the Fox’s Watchtower Mound, and there beheld Mad Qu. Knowledge posed the same questions to Mad Qu.

*—the same questions as before.*

Mad Qu replied: "Alas!"

Li, in his pronunciation notes, reads *xi* (熙) as "response." "I knew it," Knowledge said, "and was about to tell you—*you*—when, mid-speech, I forgot what I wished to say."

Failing to receive an answer, Knowledge returned to the Emperor’s palace, where he saw the Yellow Emperor and posed his questions. The Yellow Emperor replied: "Only through non-thinking and non-reflection does one grasp the Dao; only through non-dwelling and non-practice does one rest in the Dao; only through non-following and non-proceeding does one attain the Dao." Xuan remarks: "All speak of spontaneity as the accord with the Dao."

Knowledge asked the Yellow Emperor: "You and I know this, yet those two—Non-Action’s Discourse and Mad Qu—do not. Which of us is correct?" The Yellow Emperor said: "Non-Action’s Discourse is truly so; Mad Qu approximates it; you and I remain far from it. For the knowing do not speak, and the speaking do not know—thus the sage enacts the teaching of non-speech." Cheng comments: "Citing the *Laozi* as evidence."

The Dao cannot be attained, as Guo observes: "The Dao resides in spontaneity, not in attainable speech." Virtue cannot be reached, as Guo observes: "Not losing virtue is called virtue; to claim virtue is not to reach it." Benevolence may be enacted, righteousness may be diminished; to practice benevolence is permissible, to diminish benevolence for the sake of righteousness is also permissible. Ritual is mutual pretense—ritual is ornament and pretense.

Thus it is said: "Lose the Dao, then virtue follows; lose virtue, then benevolence follows; lose benevolence, then righteousness follows; lose righteousness, then ritual follows. Ritual is the flower of the Dao and the beginning of disorder." Thus it is said: "The practitioner of the Dao daily diminishes. As Guo observes: "Diminish ornament and pretense." Diminish and diminish again, until reaching non-action—through non-action, nothing is left undone." As Guo observes: "When ornament is stripped away and simplicity preserved, though one acts, it is as if one does not act." Cheng comments: "Citing the *Laozi* again to clarify its meaning."

Now that one has become a thing, as Xuan remarks: "Simplicity scattered into vessels," to return to the root is not easy! Yet how effortless it is for the great person! Life is the companion of death, death the beginning of life—who knows their order? As Xuan remarks: "Death and life cycle endlessly."

Human life is the gathering of qi: when gathered, it is life; when scattered, it is death. If death and life are companions, as Xuan remarks: "Death and life are one qi," what cause for distress have I? Thus the myriad things are one. As Xuan remarks: "The life and death of the myriad things are all one qi." What they deem beautiful is called the wondrous and divine; what they despise is called the foul and rotten. As Xuan remarks: "They esteem life as wondrous and divine, and revile death as foul and rotten." Yet the foul and rotten transform again into the wondrous and divine, and the wondrous and divine transform again into the foul and rotten.

Thus it is said: "The qi of all under heaven is one."

""

Guo remarks: "Are death and life, self and other, truly different?"
Thus the sage values oneness.

Xuan remarks: "The above all speaks of the Dao/Way."

Zhi said to the Yellow Emperor: "I asked Wuwei Wei, but Wuwei Wei did not respond to me—not that he would not respond, but that he did not know how to respond. I asked Kuang Qu, and Kuang Qu wished to tell me but did not tell me—not that he would not tell me, but that in wishing to tell me, he forgot it. Now I ask you, and you know it—why then are you not close to it?"

The Yellow Emperor said: "He is truly so because he does not know; this one seems so because he has forgotten it."

Xuan remarks: "Close to non-knowing."

"You and I will never be close to it because you know it."

Xuan remarks: "The Dao/Way fundamentally does not admit of speech."

Kuang Qu heard this and took the Yellow Emperor’s words as knowing speech.

Xuan remarks: "Wuwei Wei ultimately had no words."

Heaven and earth possess great beauty yet do not speak.

Xuan remarks: "They benefit the myriad things without speaking of the benefit."

The four seasons have bright patterns yet do not deliberate.

Xuan remarks: "The qi of the seasons is distinctly divided, requiring no deliberation."

The myriad things have principles of completion yet do not explain.

Xuan remarks: "Each has its inherent nature, needing no verbal explanation."

The sage traces back the beauty of heaven and earth and comprehends the principles of the myriad things.

*Yuan* means "origin." With the mind of covering and bearing, their origin is the same as heaven and earth. Moreover, the myriad things each have their principles of generation and completion, which they thereby comprehend.

Thus the perfected person does nothing, the great sage does not act—this is called observing heaven and earth.

Taking heaven and earth as their model.

Now that numinous brightness and utmost essence, together with those hundred transformations—above, they are heaven and earth; below, they are the myriad things.

*Ben* notes: "Originally 'now' was written as 'abandon,' changed following Liu’s edition."

Things already have death and life, square and round, yet none know their root. Things transform and vary of themselves, yet none know their root and origin.

Vastly and lightly, the myriad things have existed since antiquity.

*Bianran* means "flittingly." Since ancient times, they have eternally and firmly existed.

The six directions are vast, yet they do not depart from within; an autumn hair is minute, yet it awaits them to complete its form.

Xuan remarks: "Great has no outside; small has no gaps."

Under heaven, none do not sink and float, yet throughout life they are never constant.

Cheng remarks: "Floating and sinking, ascending and descending, ever renewing in succession."

Yin and yang and the four seasons move in their courses, each attaining its proper sequence.

Guo remarks: "Not awaiting anything to be done."

Dimly, as if lost yet present.

Cheng remarks: "Dimly, as if obscured—seemingly absent yet present."

Vital yet without form, numinous.

Vital and arising, without visible traces, transforming swiftly like the numinous.

The myriad things are nurtured yet do not know it.

The myriad things are nourished yet do not know it themselves.

This is called the root and origin; by it, one may observe heaven.

Comprehending this root and origin, one may observe the natural heaven.

Nie Que asked the Dao/Way of Pi Yi.

*Shiwen* notes: "*Pi* is pronounced *pi*; originally also written as *pi*."

Pi Yi said: "If you rectify your form, unify your gaze, the harmony of heaven will arrive.

Xuan remarks: "When body is still and spirit concentrated, the harmonious qi will naturally return."

Gather in your knowledge, unify your measures, and the spirit will come to dwell.

Yu remarks: "*Huainanzi*’s *Dao Ying* chapter and *Wenzi*’s *Dao Yuan* chapter both read 'rectify your measures.' Here, 'unify' should be 'rectify.' *Du* means 'form.'"

*Case*: When the mind is restrained and the form is rectified, the numinous brightness will naturally return.

Virtue/power will become your beauty, the Dao/Way will become your dwelling. Naturally, virtue/power and the Dao/Way will be in you.

Your gaze will be innocent like a newly born calf, seeking nothing of the old!"

Cheng remarks: "*Tongyan* is the appearance of unknowing, straightforward gaze."

*Case*: A newly born calf has a pure, undivided nature, hence the comparison.

Before he finished speaking, Nie Que fell asleep.

Pi Yi was greatly pleased, departed singing, and went on his way.

*Shiwen* notes: "His body turned toward what was said, fearing that hearing and seeing would awaken him. Having received the Dao/Way swiftly, Pi Yi rejoiced."

He said: "His form is like withered bones,

Cheng remarks: "His form is like the bones of withered wood."

*Case*: *Xu Wu Gui* chapter also reads "withered bones"; *Qi Wu Lun* reads "withered wood"; *Geng Sang Chu* reads "withered branches of wood." Since humans have a hundred bones like the many branches of a tree, "withered bones" is the same as "withered branches."

truly knowing its actuality, not holding to the old with self-possession.

Guo remarks: "Moving together with change."

Vaguely, obscurely,

*Shiwen* notes: "*Mei* is pronounced *mei*."

without mind, so that none can consult with him.

Xuan remarks: "Since he has no mind, I cannot speak to him."

What sort of person is he!"

Guo remarks: "One who transforms alone."

Shun asked Cheng: "Can the Dao/Way be obtained and possessed?"

Li remarks: "*Cheng* was Shun’s teacher. Another says: In ancient times, there were four assistants—front *yi*, rear *cheng*—likely official titles."

Cheng said: "Your body is not your own possession—how can you possess the Dao/Way?"

Shun said: "My body is not my own—who possesses it?"

Cheng said: "It is the form entrusted by heaven and earth.

Yu remarks: "*Zhongguo Celue* Gao’s commentary: '*Wei* means 'to entrust.'' *Zuozhuan* Duke Cheng 2nd year Du’s commentary: '*Wei* means 'to belong.'' The form entrusted by heaven and earth means the form that heaven and earth have entrusted and to which they belong. Below, the same."

Life is not your own—it is the harmony entrusted by heaven and earth."

"Nature and life are not your own—they are the compliance entrusted by heaven and earth. Your descendants are not your own—they are the shedding entrusted by heaven and earth.

Xuan remarks: 'Forms succeed one another in transformation, hence called *shedding*.'

Thus, in walking, one does not know where one is going; in dwelling, one does not know what one is holding; in eating, one does not know the taste. Throughout a lifetime, in walking there is a destination, yet ultimately one does not know where one is going; in dwelling there is something held, yet ultimately one does not know what one is holding; in eating there is flavor, yet ultimately one does not know the taste. This is the vigorous yang qi of heaven and earth.

Xuan remarks: 'Speaking of qi in terms of its dynamic strength.'

How then can it be obtained and possessed?"

Confucius asked Lao Dan: "Today I have leisure and tranquility—may I inquire about the supreme Dao/Way?"

Lao Dan said: "Purify your fast, dredge your mind, *shuoye* your spirit, and shatter your knowledge!

*Shiwen* notes: '*Zhi* pronounced as *zhi* (wisdom).'

Cheng remarks: '*Shu*, like washing; *xue*, like cleansing.'
Cheng remarks: '*Zaoxue*, like refining and purifying.'"

Cheng remarks: "Shatter sagely wisdom."

Ah, the Dao/Way—how profoundly ineffable! Let me speak of its rough outlines. "Outlines" means its boundaries.

Bright clarity arises from obscurity; ordered existence arises from formlessness. All things with order and sequence emerge from the formless. Spirit arises from the Dao/Way, and physical form originates from essence.
Xuan remarks: "Form here means the fundamental substance."

Thus the myriad things generate one another through form. Therefore, those with nine orifices are born from the womb—humans and beasts. Those with eight orifices are born from eggs—birds and fish. Their coming leaves no trace; their going has no boundary. Without gate, without chamber,
Xuan remarks: "Without gate, one knows not whence it emerges; without chamber, one knows not where it returns."

they radiate boundlessly in all four directions.
Xuan remarks: "Vastly pervading and all-embracing."

Those who follow this—
Cheng remarks: "This refers to the Dao/Way."
Yu remarks: "*Shuowen* lacks the character *yao*. Under the *chi* radical, it has *jiao*: 'to follow.' Also, *xun*: 'to move in accord.' Thus *yao* here means 'to follow.' Guo’s gloss of *yao* as 'to encounter' is incorrect."

—have strong limbs, penetrating thoughts,
Cheng remarks: "*Xun* means 'penetrating.'"

keen ears and sharp eyes. Their minds do not toil, and their responses to things are without fixed pattern. Heaven cannot but be high, earth cannot but be vast, the sun and moon cannot but move, and the myriad things cannot but flourish—is this not the Dao/Way?
Guo remarks: "All these are simply what cannot but be so of themselves; it is not that the Dao/Way makes them so."

Moreover, extensive learning does not necessarily bring knowledge, nor does eloquent debate necessarily bring wisdom. The sage has already settled this.
"With" and "already" are interchangeable.
Cheng remarks: "Extensive reading of the classics does not necessarily yield true knowledge; grand eloquence and ornate words do not necessarily bring enlightened insight. Thus the *Laozi* says: 'The good do not debate; those who debate are not good. The wise are not learned; the learned are not wise.' The sage has thus decisively abandoned such things."

As for that which, when augmented, does not increase, and when diminished, does not decrease—this is what the sage preserves.
Preserving what is fixed in its allotment.

Abysmally vast, it resembles the sea; toweringly high, it endlessly cycles from end to beginning.
*Shiwen* notes: "*Weiwei* is pronounced *yuwei* and read as *weiwei*."

It measures and sustains the myriad things without exhaustion—such is the gentleman’s Dao/Way. Is this external to it?
All things turn to it for sustenance without exhaustion—is this not the Dao/Way?
Su Yu remarks: "Measuring and sustaining the myriad things still leaves traces of governance and transformation, hence it is called external. All things turning to it for sustenance is like the *Yijing*’s 'sustaining generation and initiating existence'—this is the natural function of heaven and earth, hence it is called the Dao/Way."

Within the central kingdom, there is one who is neither yin nor yang,
Xuan remarks: "Blending indistinguishably between yin and yang."

dwelling between heaven and earth. Merely and provisionally human, endowed with the qi of these two realms, they are only temporarily so.
They will ultimately return to their origin.

Viewed from the root, the living are but a gathering of qi.
Li remarks: "*Yin* is pronounced *yin*; *yi* is pronounced *yi*. *Yinyi* describes the appearance of qi gathering."

That is to say, from the perspective of their original source, the living are merely a conglomeration of qi. Though they may differ in longevity, how great is the difference? All are within a hundred years. It is but a fleeting moment—why distinguish between Yao and Jie?

Fruits and melons have their principles,
*Shiwen* notes: "*Luo* is pronounced *liguo* in Xu’s reading."
Xuan remarks: "Tree fruits and herbaceous fruits do not confuse their kinds; each has its proper order."

and though human relationships are difficult, they follow their natural sequence.
Though human relationships are hard to align, their generation follows a natural order.

The sage encounters them without resistance,
Xuan remarks: "Accords with them."

passes beyond them without clinging.
Moves with them in transformation.

Harmonizing and responding—this is virtue. Coinciding and responding—this is the Dao/Way. From this arise the rise of emperors and the emergence of kings.
Guo remarks: "It is simply thus."

Man dwells between heaven and earth as a white colt passing through a crevice—
*Shiwen* notes: "Originally also written as *隙*. *隙* means an opening."
—suddenly, it is gone. The time is exceedingly brief.

Bursting forth in tumult, none fails to emerge;
Xuan remarks: "Arising into life."
Flowing smoothly and freely, none fails to enter.
*Shiwen* notes: "*漻* is pronounced *liu*."
Xuan remarks: "Returning to emptiness in death."

Having transformed and been born, then transformed and died, living beings mourn them, and humankind grieves.
Xuan remarks: "Those who face death are called *living beings*; distinguished from other beings, they are called *humankind*."

They loosen heaven’s quiver, cast off heaven’s sack—
*Shiwen* notes: "*弢*, as in *Zilin*, means ‘bow case.’ *堕* is pronounced *huigui*."
Cheng remarks: "*●* is a binding sack."
Note: This is a metaphor for the body’s constraints; in death, they are undone and discarded.

In a flurry, in a swirl—
Cheng remarks: "*纷纶* and *宛转* both describe the appearance of release."
—the hun and po depart. Gone!
The body follows after—this is the great return!

The formless form—
Xuan remarks: "The formless is that from which form arises."
Form’s formlessness—
Xuan remarks: "Form is what the formless produces."
This is what all people know in common, yet it is not what those who would reach the Dao/Way strive for.
Xuan remarks: "It is not what those who would attain the Dao/Way pursue."
This is what the multitude discusses together.

Those who have arrived do not discuss; those who discuss have not arrived.
Cheng remarks: "The perfected sage, having forgotten words and grasped principle, has nothing to discuss. If one discusses, one has not reached the Dao/Way."

Clear vision meets nothing—though one sees clearly, there is nothing to encounter.
Eloquence is not as good as silence. The Dao/Way cannot be heard; hearing is not as good as blocking the ears.
This is called the great attainment.
Cheng remarks: "One who understands this may be said to have deeply grasped the great principle."

Dongguo Zi asked Zhuangzi: "Where does the Dao/Way reside?"
Zhuangzi said: "It is nowhere absent."
Dongguo Zi said: "You must specify."
Guo remarks: "He wished Zhuangzi to name a particular location."
Zhuangzi said: "It is in the ants."
Dongguo Zi said: "How can it be so lowly?"
Zhuangzi said: "It is in the weeds and millet."
Dongguo Zi said: "How can it be even lower?"
Zhuangzi said: "It is in the tiles and bricks."
Dongguo Zi said: "How can it be still more base?"
Zhuangzi said: "It is in excrement and urine."
Dongguo Zi did not respond.

Zhuangzi said: "Your question, sir, was fundamentally unworthy of a true answer.
Cheng remarks: "*质* means *substance*. My answer to your question still falls short of reality."
The question of Zheng Huo to the market inspector treading on a pig—each step lower reveals more.
Li remarks: "*正* is a pavilion guard; *获* is his name. *监市* is the market chief. *狶* is a large pig. *履* means to tread. The market chief treads on the pig, stepping on its legs and haunches, for the pig’s fat is hardest to discern there. Thus, to inquire about the Dao/Way through ever baser things is to know the Dao/Way."
Cheng remarks: "*正* is an official title, today’s market magistrate."
Xuan remarks: "*况* means a vivid analogy."

You must not insist; there is nowhere the Dao/Way escapes from things.
Do not expect the Dao/Way to be in any one place—there is nowhere beyond things.
The supreme Dao/Way is thus; great words are also so.
Cheng remarks: "The supreme Dao/Way is principle; great words are teaching."

*Zhou*, *bian*, and *xian*—three names, one reality, their meaning is the same.
The three terms *zhou*, *bian*, and *xian* share a single purport.

Once, we wandered together in the Palace of No-What-Is—
Xuan remarks: "Roaming the mind in the realm of emptiness, one sees the Dao/Way’s unity and boundlessness."
—joining in discussion without end!

Once, we dwelled together in non-action!
Serene and still!
Vast and clear!
Guo Qingfan remarks: "*漠* also means *clear*. *Shigu* states: ‘*漠* and *察* mean *clear*.’ Fan’s note says: ‘*漠然* means clear in appearance.’"
Harmonious and at ease!
Harmonized and leisurely.

My aspiration is emptiness—
Empty and still, that is my aspiration.
I go nowhere and yet do not know where I arrive; I depart and return without knowing where I rest.
I have gone and come without knowing my end; swift in coming and going, there is no final limit.
I wander in boundless vastness—
Guo remarks: "*冯闳* means *vast emptiness*."
—great wisdom enters and does not know its limit.
The great sage enters and roams freely with transformations.

That which things things is without boundary with things—
That which things things is the Dao/Way. Where things are, the Dao/Way is, thus it has no limit with things.
Yet things have boundaries—what is called the boundary of things—
Cheng remarks: "Each thing has its own limit; this is merely called the boundary of things. How can one speak of the Dao/Way?"
—is the boundary of the unbounded, the boundary that is no boundary.
The Dao/Way is fundamentally unbounded, yet appears in the boundaries of things. It is the boundary that is no boundary.

We speak of fullness and emptiness, growth and decline—yet that which is fullness is not fullness, that which is decline is not decline.
Cheng remarks: "Wealth and honor are fullness; poverty and lowliness are emptiness; old age and sickness are decline."
That which is beginning and end is not beginning and end; that which is accumulation and dispersal is not accumulation and dispersal.
Cheng remarks: "End and beginning are beginning and end; coming to life is accumulation; dying is dispersal."

E He Gan studied with Lao Long Ji alongside Shen Nong.

Shen Nong leaned upon his armrest, closed the door, and dozed at midday. E He Gan pushed open the door at noon and entered, saying, "Old Long is dead!" Shen Nong, still leaning upon his armrest, grasped his staff and rose, then clapped it down with a laugh. He said, "Heaven knew my perversity, rudeness, and arrogance—thus it cast me aside and let him die. Alas! The Master has died without ever uttering his wild words to me!"

E He Gan’s commentary: "Wild words are the utmost words. They are not what the world understands, so the utmost words are called wild."

Yan Gang Diao heard of this. Li’s commentary: "Yan Gang was a man who embodied the Dao; Diao was his name." Xuan’s commentary: "Yan Gang came to offer condolences." He said, "Those who embody the Dao are what the noble ones of the world depend upon." Guo’s commentary: "To say one embodies the Dao is to call them the master of all people. Yet now, in matters of the Dao, even the tip of an autumn hair—one ten-thousandth of which is not yet attained—you still knew to conceal his wild words and let him die. How much more so for those who truly embody the Dao! They are formless to the eye, soundless to the ear. In the discourse of men, they are called the obscure. Thus, to discourse on the Dao is not the Dao itself."

Guo’s commentary: "Even the obscure is not the Dao—this clarifies that the Dao is nameless."

Then Tai Qing asked Wu Qiong, "Do you know the Dao?" Wu Qiong said, "I do not." He then asked Wu Wei. Wu Wei said, "I know the Dao." Tai Qing said, "In your knowing of the Dao, are there principles?" Wu Wei said, "There are." Tai Qing said, "What are these principles?" Wu Wei said, "I know the Dao can be noble or base, can gather or disperse. These are the principles by which I know the Dao."

Cheng’s commentary: "Noble as a ruler, base as a servant; gathering is life, dispersing is death—these principles are boundless."

Tai Qing took these words to ask Wu Shi, who said the same. Tai Qing said, "If this is so, then is Wu Qiong’s not-knowing or Wu Wei’s knowing correct, and which is incorrect?" Wu Shi said, "Not-knowing is profound; knowing is shallow. Not-knowing is internal; knowing is external."

Thereupon Tai Qing sighed and said, "Is not-knowing then knowing? Is knowing then not-knowing? Who knows the knowing of not-knowing?" Wu Shi said, "The Dao cannot be heard; to hear it is not it. The Dao cannot be seen; to see it is not it. The Dao cannot be spoken; to speak it is not it. Do you know the form of the formless? The Dao is not to be named."

As above: "The form of the formless, the formless of form." Knowing the form of the formless is to know the Dao is not to be designated by name.

Wu Shi said, "Those who ask about the Dao and receive an answer do not know the Dao. Even those who ask about it have not heard the Dao. The one who answers is certainly wrong, and the one who asks is also not right. The Dao has no questions; questions have no answers."

Guo’s commentary: "This is the meaning of abandoning learning and teaching to return to naturalness." To ask where there is no question is to ask the unanswerable. To answer where there is no answer is to be without inwardness. To force an answer where none is possible is to follow externals—thus it is called "without inwardness." Facing the unanswerable with no inwardness, one like this outwardly does not observe the cosmos, inwardly does not know the Great Beginning. They do not understand the principles of things within the six directions, nor do they know the Dao’s root lies within themselves. Thus, they do not pass beyond Kunlun, nor do they wander in the Great Void.

How could one surpass Kunlun and wander in the Great Void?

Guang Yao asked Wu You, "Do you exist, or do you not exist?" Guang Yao could not complete his question. Yu’s commentary: "The *Huainanzi*’s *Dao Ying Xun* has five characters above this line: 'Wu You did not respond.' This should be included; without it, the meaning is incomplete." He then stared intently at Wu You’s appearance, as if scrutinizing it closely.

Gazing into emptiness, utterly void—all day long one may look yet not see, listen yet not hear, grasp yet not obtain.

Guang Yao said, "This is perfection! Who could ever attain such a state? I can attain being and non-being, yet I cannot attain non-being beyond non-being." Xuan comments: "To possess non-being as substance is to attain being and non-being, but one has not yet reached the superiority of absolute nothingness." "And if one becomes 'without having,' how could one reach this?"

Xuan comments: "Even if one becomes 'without,' one has not yet escaped from being—how could one reach the realm of non-being beyond non-being?"

The Master Hammerman of Da Ma—Cheng comments: "Da Ma was the Grand Marshal of Chu. *Chui* means to forge; *gou* refers to a belt hook."—was eighty years old yet never lost the breadth of a hair. Sima and Guo comment: "In judging the weight of a forged hook, he never erred by even a hair’s breadth."

Da Ma said, "Is it skill, or is it the Way?"

He replied, "I have my *shou*." Wang Niansun comments: "*Shou* is the same as *Dao*. In the *Da Sheng* chapter, Confucius says, 'Is it skill, or is it the Way?' and the reply is, 'I have the Way.'"

"""

This serves as its proof. The character *Dao* was anciently pronounced like *shou*, hence interchangeable with it. In the rhymed writings of the *Nine Classics*, *Chu Ci*, Laozi, Zhuangzi, and other masters, *Dao* is always read as *shou*. The *Shuowen* states: "Dao is written with the radical *chuò* and the phonetic *shǒu*." The modern edition omits the phonetic, as the two Xus failed to recognize the ancient pronunciation.

At twenty, your servant took delight in forging belt hooks, seeing nothing else in the world—nothing but hooks did I observe. Su Yu comments: "This is the same principle as not allowing the myriad things to displace the cicada’s wings." Thus, what is used relies upon what is not used to long sustain its use. Cheng comments: "That he retained his skill in forging hooks into old age was due to never applying his mind to observing other things." How much more so for one who uses nothing at all! To not use is already excellent, but to even be without this "not using"—this is what is called "non-being beyond non-being." What thing does not draw upon it? Thus, all things rely on its use.

Ran Qiu asked Confucius: "Can one know what preceded heaven and earth?"
Confucius said: "Yes. The past was like the present."
Guo comments: "Heaven and earth have always existed; there was never a time before them."

Ran Qiu departed, having posed his question poorly. The next day, he returned and said: "Yesterday, I asked whether one could know what preceded heaven and earth, and you replied, 'Yes. The past was like the present.' Yesterday, I understood clearly, but today I am confused. May I ask what you meant?"
Confucius said: "Yesterday’s clarity came because your spirit received it first. Guo comments: "To await destiny with an empty mind is to receive with the spirit." Today’s confusion arises because you now seek it with what is not spirit. What is not spirit is traces and images. Attached to traces and images, you seek further enlightenment. There is no past, no present, no beginning, no end—all are transformations of a single *qi*. Could there be descendants before there were descendants?" Xuan comments: "Descendants can emerge from nothing, but can heaven and earth emerge from nothing?"

Ran Qiu did not respond.
Confucius said: "Enough! No need to answer. Do not let life give rise to death, nor death give rise to life. The dead die of themselves; their life does not give rise to this death. The living live of themselves; their death does not give rise to this life. Do life and death depend on one another? Each has its own integrity. Is there something that existed before heaven and earth? It is the same as this. That which gives rise to things is not itself a thing. What gives rise to things is the *Dao*—it cannot be called a thing. Things do not emerge before other things; the myriad things emerge together, none preceding another. Yet they all have their being. They all have their being, and this has no end. For what gives rise to things is ever present. The sage’s love for others has no end, for it is drawn from this."

Yan Yuan asked Confucius: "I once heard you say, 'Do not send off, do not welcome.'" Cheng comments: "The sage is like a mirror, neither sending off nor welcoming." "May I ask where the mind wanders?"
Confucius said: "The ancients transformed outwardly while remaining inwardly unchanged. Xuan comments: "They moved with things, but the heavenly ruler remained unmoved." People today transform inwardly while remaining outwardly unchanged—their spirit drifts and stagnates upon things. One who transforms with things is one who does not transform. Guo comments: "Always without mind, thus one and unchanging; only one who is unchanging can transform with things." To be at ease with transformation or non-transformation, Cheng comments: "At ease means to follow. The sage, without mind, flows with things, whether transforming or not, all is followed with ease." to be at ease and in harmony with them, Cheng comments: "In harmony means to follow." Note: To follow and be in harmony with them. must be in accord with them without excess. Cheng comments: "Though in harmony with things, each remains within its bounds, neither harming the other." The preserves of Xiwei Shi, the gardens of the Yellow Emperor, the palaces of Youyu, the halls of Tang and Wu—with each descending age, the dwellings grew narrower, yet the sage adapts to the times and finds contentment. The noble person, like the masters of the Confucians and Mohists, takes them as teachers, yet even with them uses right and wrong to harmonize—how much more so with people today! Guo comments: "The masters of the Confucians and Mohists are the most difficult in the world to harmonize with, yet one without mind still harmonizes with them—how much more so with ordinary people!" Note: The noble person can transform with people today. The sage dwells among things without harming them. Xuan comments: "Without mind for right or wrong." One who does not harm things cannot be harmed by them. Only one who harms nothing can send off and welcome others. Without sending off or welcoming is acceptable; sending off and welcoming are also acceptable. Oh, the mountains and forests! Oh, the hills and wilds!"

Oh, how joyous and delightful it is! The plains and wilds—oh, the open fields! Yet joy is not yet complete when sorrow follows after. Cheng comments: "Emotions shift with circumstances; sorrow and joy thus change. From this we know that the world’s sorrows and joys are not worth reckoning." When sorrow and joy arrive, I cannot resist them; when they depart, I cannot halt them. How lamentable! The people of the world are but travelers lodging in things. Guo comments: "They cannot sit in forgetfulness and find contentment within, but instead become hostages to sorrow and joy." Note: They are but guesthouses for external things. Those who know what they encounter yet do not know what they do not encounter—encounters have their limits, and knowledge has its bounds. Knowing what one can do yet not knowing what one cannot do—knowledge takes ability as its capacity, yet does not recognize that inability is also a kind of capacity. To be without knowledge and without ability is something no person can avoid. Xuan comments: "Knowledge and ability are boundless, so there will always be things one does not know and cannot do—this is the common lot of humanity." Those who strive to avoid what no person can avoid—Xuan comments: "They exhaust their minds with conjecture, hoping to attain omniscience and omnipotence." How lamentable indeed! Cheng comments: "What utter folly." The ultimate word transcends words; the ultimate action transcends action. Cheng comments: "The word of the ultimate principle is beyond words that can be spoken; the action of the ultimate principle is beyond actions that can be performed." To equalize what knowledge knows is but shallow. Xuan comments: "If one insists on using what knowledge knows to equalize all things, demanding omniscience, how can this be the way of one who has seen the Dao?"

Among the disciples of Lao Dan—*Sima comments: "役 refers to students or followers"*—was one named Gengsang Chu—*Yu comments: "In the *Confucius* chapter of the *Liezi*, there is one named Kangcangzi among Lao Dan’s disciples, and Zhang Zhan’s commentary notes: 'Pronounced Gengsang.' Jia Kui’s *Genealogical Survey of Surnames* states: 'In Wu Commandery, there is a Gengsang clan, counted among the seven great families.' Thus, was Gengsangzi a man of Wu?"*—who had partially grasped Lao Dan’s Dao and dwelt in the north upon Mount Weilü—*Li comments: "Weilü is the name of a mountain. Some say it is in Lu; others, in Liangzhou."*

Those among his retainers who were cunningly clever departed from him; those among his concubines who were ostentatiously benevolent kept their distance. The people of the land revered and cherished Gengsang, wishing to serve as his retainers and concubines. Yet among them were those who flaunted their keen discernment as wisdom, and those who paraded their self-proclaimed virtue as benevolence—Gengsang kept them all at a distance. Those who remained with him were the ungainly—*Sima comments: "拥肿 refers to an ugly appearance"*—and those who served as errand-runners, the *yangzhang*—*Yangzhang: those who toil and rush about in labor.*

After three years, Weilü flourished abundantly—*Li’s commentary: "壤 is also written as 穰. The Guangya states: 'It means abundance.'" Lu notes: "The *Liezi*’s 'Heaven’s Blessings' chapter also uses 壤 for 穰."* The people of Weilü said to one another: "When Gengsangzi first arrived, we were startled by him—*Cui and Li comment: "洒然 describes a state of astonishment."* Now, when we reckon our days, we find them insufficient—*Xiang explains: "There is no small profit from dawn to dusk."* Yet when we reckon our years, we find them overflowing—*Xiang explains: "We prosper in harmony with the seasons."* He must be a sage! Why do we not together enthrone him as our lord and offer him sacrifices as to the gods of soil and grain?"—*尸 means "lord." They wished to honor him as their ruler.*

When Gengsangzi heard this, he turned southward with unease. *This passage also appears in the 'Equalizing Things' chapter.* His disciples were perplexed. Gengsangzi said: "Disciples, why are you perplexed by me? When spring’s qi stirs, the hundred grasses sprout; when autumn arrives in its due season, the ten thousand treasures ripen—*Yu comments: "The character 得 is likely an interpolation from the following line. The *Yi Jing*’s 'Shuo Gua' states: 'Dui corresponds to the true autumn, the season in which the myriad things find joy in completion.' The commentary explains: 'In true autumn, the myriad things all joyfully come to fruition.' This passage suffices in meaning without the 得 character."* Do spring and autumn act thus without reason? The Dao of Heaven has already unfolded—*The *Shiwen* reads "Heaven" as "Great." Note: Time and the Dao move in their course, achieving without awareness.*

I have heard that the perfected person dwells in stillness within a walled chamber, *Xuan comments: "Living in seclusion without ostentation."* while the common people roam wildly, unaware of where to turn—*Xuan comments: "As if forgotten by Heaven and Earth."* Now, with the petty folk of Weilü secretly scheming to honor me among the ranks of the worthy, am I to become their measuring mark?—*Guo comments: "He did not wish to serve as a standard for others."* This is why I remain unsettled by Lao Dan’s words."—*Cheng comments: "Laozi said: 'When the work is done, do not dwell in it; lead without dominating.' Gengsang reverently followed his master’s teaching, while the people of Weilü did the opposite—hence his unease."*

His disciples said: "It is not so."

The shallow ditches of a fathom’s length offer no room for great fish to turn their bodies, yet eels and minnows thrive there—*Cheng comments: "Eight chi make a xun; twice that, a chang. Ni refers to small fish."* *Shiwen* adds: "Zhi means to bend. The small fish can twist and turn."* Note: "Zhi" and "zhe" were interchangeable in ancient script. The low hills of a few paces’ height and a ren’s rise provide no concealment for massive beasts, yet foxes find them auspicious—*Shiwen* notes: "Six chi make a bu; seven chi, a ren. A hill one bu wide and one ren high."* *Cui comments: "Xiang means good. Foxes deem such small mounds favorable."*

Moreover, to honor the worthy and empower the capable, to bestow goodness and profit first upon the virtuous—such has been the way since the days of Yao and Shun. *Note: "Yi" and "yi" are interchangeable.* How much more should the people of Weilü follow this? Master, you should heed this as well!"

Gengsangzi replied: "Come here, young ones! The beast that fills a carriage, once it leaves the mountains alone—*Li comments: "Han refers to a capacious carriage."* *Yu comments: "The *Fangyan* states: 'A beast without a mate is called jie.'"*—cannot escape the snares and nets. The fish that swallows a boat, once stranded on dry land—*Shiwen* notes: "Dang, read as *tulang*. It means to be cast ashore and lose the water."*—is tormented even by ants. Thus birds and beasts do not tire of lofty heights, nor do fish and turtles tire of profound depths—*Guo comments: "Only by distancing oneself from profit and harm can one remain whole."*

For one who preserves their form and life, to conceal oneself is simply to shun neither depth nor obscurity. To be as things are. As for those two—referring to Yao and Shun above—what merit is there in praising them? In their distinctions—*Xuan comments: "Such as honoring the worthy, empowering the capable, bestowing goodness and profit first."*—they were like those who recklessly bore holes in walls to plant mugwort and wormwood—*Guo comments: "They would lead later generations to recklessly bore through and sow disorder."* Picking through hair to comb it, measuring grains of rice to cook—such trivialities! How could such things save the age?

Exalting the worthy sets the people against one another—*Zha means to vie.* Relying on cunning invites theft among them—*Xuan comments: "Dao means deceit."* These few measures cannot enrich the people. The people are relentless in their pursuit of profit: sons kill fathers, ministers murder rulers—*Shiwen* notes: "Sha is read as shi; the text also has shi (regicide)."* In broad daylight, they rob; at noon, they bore through walls—*Shiwen* notes: "Xiang is read as pei. *Bi* means wall. They act without fear or shame."*

""

I tell you: the root of great disorder must arise between the eras of Yao and Shun, while its branches will endure into a thousand generations hence. A thousand generations from now, there will surely be men who devour one another."

This passage also appears in the *Xu Wugui* chapter. Nanrong Chu straightened himself and sat upright, saying—*Shiwen* notes: "Chu is read as *chú* (昌于反); Xiang reads it as *chóu*. Li explains: 'A disciple of Gengsang.' The *Renzhong* text writes it as *Chóu*; the *Huainanzi* as *Chóu*." Lu comments: "The *Huainanzi* now writes it as *Chóu*."—"For one of my years, I have already grown old. How then shall I entrust my studies to attain these words?"

*Wu* is read as *wū*. Cheng comments: "What learning shall I rely upon to reach such words?"

Gengsangzi said: "Preserve your form whole, embrace your life,—Yu comments: "*Shiming* states: 'Embrace (*bào*) means preserve (*bǎo*); to be close and preserve.' Thus *bào* and *bǎo* share the same meaning. 'Embrace your life' is to preserve your life."—and let not your thoughts and cares flutter restlessly. If you do this for three years, you may then attain these words."

Nanrong Chu said:

"The eye and the form—I know not their difference, yet the blind cannot see themselves; the ear and the form—I know not their difference, yet the deaf cannot hear themselves; the heart and the form—I know not their difference, yet the mad cannot grasp themselves. The forms are the same, but their functions differ.

The form and the form are also illustrative—Guo Songtao notes: '*Liji* *Daxue* commentary: "Illustrative (*bì*) means 'to make understood.'" That is, the form and the form are easily understood.'—Yet I say: my form and the form of others are also easily understood. Yet why do things sometimes come between them?—Xuan comments: 'Things (*wù*) refer to desires.'—Why do they seek one another yet fail to attain one another?

There are always those who cannot understand one another, so I suspect some barrier divides them. Now I say to Chu: 'Preserve your form whole, embrace your life, and let not your thoughts and cares flutter restlessly.' Chu has merely heard the Dao with his ears."—*Shiwen* notes: "Cui and Xiang say: 'He has only reached the ears, not yet penetrated the heart.'"—

Gengsangzi said: "My words are exhausted. Thus says the ancient saying."

"The running bee cannot transform the *huozhu* caterpillar—*Sima* [Biao] comments: 'The running bee is a small wasp; another says it is the earth wasp. The *huozhu* is the large green worm found in bean leaves.'—*Cheng* [Xuanying] comments: 'The slender-waisted earth wasp can transform the mulberry worm into its own offspring, yet it cannot transform the *huozhu*.' The Yue chicken cannot brood the swan’s egg, but the Lu chicken certainly can."—*Shiwen* notes: "Xiang [Xiu] says: 'The Yue chicken is a small chicken; some say it is the Jing chicken. The Lu chicken is a large chicken, like the present-day Shu chicken.' *Hu* is also written as *he* (crane), both being the same."

"Between chicken and chicken, their virtue is not different, yet some can and some cannot—this is simply because their capacities are inherently great or small. Now my capacity is small and insufficient to transform you. Why do you not go south to see Laozi?"

Nanrong Chu packed provisions and traveled seven days and seven nights until he reached Laozi’s place.—*Shiwen* notes: "*Fangyan* says: '*Ying* means to carry. In Qi, Chu, Chen, and Song, it is called *ying*.'"

Laozi said: "Have you come from the region of Chu?"

Nanrong Chu replied: "Yes."

Laozi said: "Why have you come with such a crowd of people?"

Nanrong Chu, startled, looked back over his shoulder.—*Juran* means the same as *quran* (startled).

Laozi said: "Do you not understand what I meant?"

Nanrong Chu bowed his head in shame, then looked up and sighed, saying: "Just now I forgot my answer and thus lost my question."

Laozi said: "What do you mean?"

Nanrong Chu said: "Is it not so? People call me Zhu the Foolish."—Guo Songtao comments: "Du Yu’s *Zuo Zhuan* annotation for the fourth year of Duke Xiang says: 'Short and small is called *zhuru*.' *Zhu yu* likely refers to one whose wisdom and skill are short and small."—Su Yu comments: "This case: *Zhu yu* is like *zhuan yu*. *Zhu* and *zhuan* are double-voiced characters."—"Is it so? On the contrary, my body troubles me with sorrow."

"Without *ren* (benevolence), one harms others; with *ren*, one instead brings sorrow upon oneself. Without *yi* (righteousness), one injures others; with *yi*, one instead brings sorrow upon one’s own person. Where can I flee to escape this? These three sayings are what trouble me, and I wish to ask you about them through Chu."

Laozi said: "Just now, I saw it in the space between your brows and lashes—I thereby understood you, and already grasped your heart. Now you speak again and confirm it. You know my words are proven. Yet you stand there rigidly, as if you had lost your father and mother—like raising a pole to seek the sea. As I said before: 'Using something short and small to measure a deep and vast domain.' You are like a lost man! Bewildered, you wish to return to your nature and disposition yet have no way in—how pitiable!"

Nanrong Chu asked to enter and rest in his lodging. He summoned what he loved and cast aside what he despised. He called forth clarity and emptiness, and removed material desires. For ten days he grieved over himself, for he had not yet attained it. Then he saw Laozi again.

Laozi said: "You have cleansed yourself, washed away the filth. How dense and oppressive! Yet within, there still lingers something noxious. One bound externally cannot be grasped by force—one must close inwardly. One bound internally cannot be grasped through confusion—one must close outwardly. Those bound externally and internally, whose *Dao* and *virtue* cannot hold firm, are like those fettered by external and internal desires. Even if they possess *Dao* and *virtue*, they cannot uphold them—how much less those who abandon the *Dao* and act!"

Nanrong Chu said: "When a villager is ill, and another villager asks about it, the sick one can describe his illness, yet the one who is truly ill remains unwell. The one who asks about the illness is the one who is ill. When I hear of the great *Dao*, it is like taking medicine that only worsens the disease. I wish only to hear the *Canon of Preserving Life*."

Laozi said: "The *Canon of Preserving Life*—can you embrace the One? Can you not lose it? Can you know good and ill fortune without divination? Can you come to rest? Can you cease? Can you abandon others and seek it within yourself? Can you be free and unconstrained? Can you be simple and unknowing? Can you be like a little child? A child cries all day, yet its throat does not grow hoarse—such is the perfection of harmony. It clenches its fists all day, yet its hands do not cramp—such is the constancy of its *virtue*. It gazes all day without blinking—its focus is not on externals. It walks without knowing where it goes, sits without knowing what it does, moves with things in perfect accord, riding the same waves. All these describe the little child. This is the *Canon of Preserving Life*."

Southridge Chu asked, "Then is this the *virtue* of the perfected person?"

He replied, "No. Is this merely what is called the ability to melt ice and thaw frost? This is but the capacity to dissolve the congealed obstructions within the breast. As for the perfected person, they share sustenance with the earth and commune in joy with Heaven. They do not entangle themselves with the gains and harms of others, nor do they engage in strangeness or set themselves apart. They do not conspire with others, nor do they casually conform. They do not involve themselves in affairs, nor do they lightly form attachments. They come and go with effortless grace, arriving and departing with uncontrived simplicity."

"This is called the *canon of preserving life*."

He asked, "Then is this the ultimate?"

"Has it reached the utmost?"

He replied, "No. I have already told you: *'Can you be like an infant?'*
The infant moves without knowing what it does, walks without knowing where it goes. Its body is like the branch of a withered tree, and its heart is like dead ashes. Such is the state described in *The Equality of Things and Lives*, and also in *Xu Wugui* and *Knowledge Wanders North*, where 'body' is written as 'frame.' In this state, misfortune does not arrive, nor does fortune come. With neither misfortune nor fortune, how could there be human-caused calamity?"

Guo Xiang comments: "Misfortune and fortune arise from loss and gain; human calamity stems from love and aversion."

Xuan notes: "The reply is 'No,' yet no further words are given. For the ultimate Dao lies not beyond what was said earlier—only when the heart takes this as the ultimate does it cease to be the Dao. Thus Laozi strips it away."

When the vastness of the cosmos settles into stillness, it radiates the light of Heaven. Guo comments: "When the virtue of the cosmos is vast and settled, what it emits is the light of Heaven, not the brilliance of man." One who radiates the light of Heaven appears as a human being. Xuan notes: "Seen from a human perspective, such a one is merely human."

Those who cultivate themselves now possess constancy. Xuan notes: "Cultivation here means the settled vastness; constancy refers to pure and enduring stability." Those who possess constancy are sheltered by others and aided by Heaven. People come to rely on them, and Heaven also grants its protection. Those sheltered by others are called the people of Heaven—exalted without rank. Those aided by Heaven are called the sons of Heaven. Learners study what they cannot study; practitioners practice what they cannot practice; debaters debate what they cannot debate. Xuan notes: "In all three, they do not know where to stop." To know where to stop in what one cannot know—this is perfection. Cheng comments: "What one cannot know should not be forced; this is the ultimate subtlety of learning."

If there are those who do not abide by this, the balance of Heaven will ruin them. Cheng comments: "Those who do not take what lies within their nature as right will corrupt their inherent nature." Equip the self with all things to sustain form, and possess all principles to accord with form. Conceal the unforeseen to give rise to the heart. Xuan notes: "Retreat to the realm of no thought, to enliven the heart." Maintain reverence within to reach without—be respectful of inner wisdom to extend it outward.

If, despite this, myriad evils still arrive—Xuan notes: "Referring to calamities"—such as King Wen’s imprisonment in Youli or Confucius’s distress in Kuang, these are all from Heaven, not from man. Xuan notes: "Not brought about by oneself." They are not enough to disturb completion, nor to disrupt one’s great accomplishment. They cannot enter the sacred terrace. They cannot disturb the heart by causing one to be moved. Guo comments: "The sacred terrace is the heart." The sacred terrace has something it holds, yet one does not know what it holds, and it is that which cannot be held. The heart must not be moved by things; its value lies in being able to hold itself. Yet it should hold itself naturally, not with deliberate grasping.

One does not see its sincerity toward oneself and so acts; every action is thus misplaced. One has not seen its sincerity toward the self and so acts recklessly; though it acts, it will not be fitting. When karmic matters enter and do not depart, each change only compounds the error. Cheng comments: "Karmic matters refer to affairs." External affairs enter and disturb the heart without leaving; though they change, they still result in loss. Yao notes: "The 'self' above refers to this. If one does not see its sincerity, then all is delusion. Acting thus, nothing will be right. If matters enter but one cannot abide long, changing only leads to further loss—this is knowing but failing to preserve." This interpretation is also coherent.

To do evil in the open and bright is to be punished by others; to do evil in the hidden and secluded is to be punished by ghosts. Only one who is enlightened to both the human and the ghostly can act alone. Guo comments: "With nothing to be ashamed of in the hidden or the visible, one can act alone without fear." Those who hold the inner contract act without reputation. Xuan notes: "The contract is an agreement, a harmony within." Cheng comments: "Without reputation is the Dao. Those who tread the Dao act without leaving a trace." Those who hold the outer contract aspire to extravagant expectations.

The *Xunzi* frequently employs the character *qi* (綦). Yang's commentary on the "Wang Ba" chapter states: "*Qi* means 'extreme.'" It may also appear as *qi* (期). *Qi fei* (期費) thus means "extreme expenditure," where *fei* refers to material resources.
