To note: "Those who hold the outer contract aspire to *qi fei*" means that those who align with external expectations desire to exhaust their resources. "Those who act without reputation" are but the commonplace made radiant—ordinary yet luminous. "Those who aspire to *qi fei*" are but merchants; how do they differ from merchants? People see their striving and liken them to towering market figures. Those who exhaust themselves with things find things entering them. Guo notes: "*Qiong* (穷) refers to beginning and end." Xuan comments: "If I remain with things from beginning to end, things will likewise come to me." Those who deal with things in a haphazard manner cannot even contain themselves; how can they contain others? Yu comments: "*Qie* (且) means 'careless.' The *Book of Songs*' *Dongmen zhi fen* reads *gu dan yu chai* (谷旦于差), where the *Han Shi* renders *dan* (旦) as *qie* (且), stating: 'It means careless.' Thus, reduplicated, it signifies carelessness, while alone, it means *qie*. The earlier 'beginning and end' conveys the sense of exhaustiveness, which is the very opposite of carelessness."

Those who cannot contain others have no intimacy; those without intimacy are wholly others. Guo states: "They are entirely other people." No weapon is more *can* (惨) than the will; the *Mo Ye* sword is inferior. The *Shuowen* says: "*Can* means 'poisonous.'" The character may also appear as *●* (a variant of 惨). Nothing is more poisonous than the heart, with weapons ranking below it. No enemy is greater than yin and yang; there is no escape from them between heaven and earth. Cheng comments: "*Kou* (寇) means 'enemy.'" It is not yin and yang that harm them, but the heart that commands it. Guo states: "When the heart commands qi, yin and yang congeal as *zheng* (症) in the five viscera."

The Dao flows unimpeded through all divisions. Xuān comments: "All divisions necessarily have boundaries. The Dao has no boundaries, thus it flows through all that it divides." Its completion is its destruction. Where this is accomplished, that is undone—hence the Dao makes no distinction between completion and destruction. What is detestable about division is that division strives for perfection. All divisions seek perfection, thus division is detestable. What is detestable about perfection is that it possesses the means for perfection. Those who possess the means for perfection still seek it endlessly, thus perfection is detestable. Thus, to go forth and not return is to reveal one’s ghostly nature. When the emotions and perceptions drift outward without returning, one sees only their ghostly aspect. To go forth and obtain is called "obtaining death." To drift outward and thus gain something, they deem it an attainment, yet it is but the attainment of death. To be extinguished yet possess substance is one form of ghostliness. Though their nature is extinguished, though they possess the substance of form and bones, and deem themselves living, I regard them as but one kind of ghost. It is by possessing form that one models the formless and thus attains stability. When humans possess form, they should regard it as formless—then the heavenly ruler is perfectly at peace.

To go forth without root—this is the flowing of the Dao without origin. To enter without aperture—this is the gathering of the Dao without crevice. It possesses substance yet has no place. The Dao has substance yet no discernible location. It has length yet no root or tip. *Shiwen* notes: "*Piāo* (剽) is also written as *biāo* (摽). Cuī comments: 'It means the tip.'" (Note: The distant branches of a tree are called *biāo*, hence it signifies the tip.) The source and flow of the Dao are vast, yet no root or tip is seen. That which emerges without aperture possesses substance. That which possesses substance yet has no place is space. That which emerges without crevice, though not without substance, ultimately has no location—it abides in the space of the four directions and above and below. That which has length yet no root or tip is time. Though it has length, its beginning and end are the time of past and present. There is life, there is death, there is going forth, there is entering. Beings have life and death; yin and yang have going forth and entering. Going forth and entering without revealing their form—this is called the Heavenly Gate. Guo comments: "The Heavenly Gate is the collective name for the myriad things. To call it the Heavenly Gate is like calling it the Gate of All Wonders." The Heavenly Gate is Nonbeing. The myriad things emerge from Nonbeing. Guo comments: "It takes Nonbeing as its gate." Being cannot take Being as its source. Before Being arises, it is not something Being can possess. It must emerge from Nonbeing. Nonbeing can generate Being. Yet there is not even Nonbeing. Xuān comments: "Even the term 'Nonbeing' is negated—this is where all wonders reside, thus the sage abides here."

The knowledge of the ancients reached its utmost. To what did it reach? Some held that there had never yet been anything—this is the utmost, the exhaustion of all, beyond which nothing can be added. Others held that there were things. (This is also seen in the "Equalizing Discussion of Things" chapter.) They took life as loss and death as return. Cheng comments: "The common person takes life as gain and death as loss. Now, desiring to return to the essence of enlightenment, they take life as loss—for it is nothingness—and death as return, a return to emptiness. Though they have not fully attained the wonders of all things, they can still equalize life and death." Thus, they are already divided. They are the same as themselves. Guo comments: "Though they seek to equalize, they are already divided." Cheng comments: "They still see the difference between life and death." Others said that first there was Nonbeing, then there was life, and life swiftly turned to death. They took Nonbeing as the head, life as the body, and death as the tail. Who knows the one who guards the unity of Being, Nonbeing, life, and death? I would befriend such a one. Those of yet another level also know that Being, Nonbeing, life, and death are one and guard this without doubt. Whoever understands this principle, I would also befriend.

These three, though different—Guo comments: "Some take Being as Nonbeing, some take Being as one, some divide yet equalize—thus they are called three."—though they exhaust or do not exhaust, all can keep their breasts free of right and wrong. They are of the ducal clan. The ducal clan of Chu had not yet received a surname, such as the king’s sons and grandsons. The Zhao and Jing clans took their ancestors as their names. Xuān comments: "These two clans took the ancestors they honored as their surnames." The Jia clan took their fief as their name. "Jia" is a corruption of "Zhong." Xuān emends it, and I follow. This clan took the fief they were enfeoffed with as their surname. They are not one. They are also like the three above, sharing a single origin.

At birth, all is soot—*Xuān* glosses: "Xú reads *jiǎn* (徐于减反). The *Zìlín* states: 'The blackness at the bottom of a cauldron.'" *Xuān* comments: "All living things emerge from obscurity, like the uniform blackness coating the bottom of a cauldron, without distinction of self or other." Yet if one suddenly declares to another, "You must shift your allegiance to this," such a claim generates right and wrong from oneself. Though I have spoken of shifting allegiance, let me try to articulate it—yet it is not something that can truly be spoken. *Xuān* comments: "It is fundamentally inexpressible." Nevertheless, it remains unknowable. Yet the world does not understand this.

At the year-end sacrifice, the tripe and hooves are present—these may be dispersed, yet they may not be dispersed. *Chéng* comments: "The *là* is the great sacrifice. *Pí* refers to the cow’s omasum; *gāi* means 'complete,' though some say it refers to the cow’s hooves. During the *là* sacrifice, the sacrificial animals are fully prepared, with all four limbs and five viscera laid out. Once the rites conclude, they may be dispersed—hence dispersion is permissible. Yet if the sacrifice remains unfinished, they must not be dispersed—thus dispersion is impermissible."

Observe a chamber, and one surveys its halls and ancestral temples, then proceeds to its secluded recesses—*Shìwén* notes: "Sīmǎ and Guō say: '*Yǎn* refers to the side of the screen.'" Guì Fù comments: "'*Píng* should read *pìn*. The *Jíjiùpiān* states, '*Pìn*, privies, clean and filthy, dung and soil,' where Yán’s annotation reads: '*Pìn* is the name for secluded recesses.'" Guō Qìngfān comments: "'*Yǎn* should be *yàn*. The *Zhōulǐ*’s *Gōngrén* states, 'They prepare its well and *yàn*,' where Elder Zhèng explains: '*Yàn* is the roadside privy.' The *Yàncè* reads, 'The King of Sòng cast images of the feudal lords and had them attend the screen and *yàn*'—*píngyàn* thus refers to privies." This gives rise to the shifting of right and wrong.

Let me attempt to speak of this shifting. The scattering or retention of even the smallest things has its proper time; the observation of a single chamber must be exhaustive. Yet because of this, one raises the matter of shifting right and wrong—then let me try to speak of this shifting. Here, life is taken as the root, and knowledge as the teacher. This means taking one’s own life as the foundation and one’s own mind’s knowledge as the guide. Thereby, one rides upon right and wrong, thus generating disputes over them. If there are indeed names and realities to contend over, one takes oneself as the standard, making others use oneself as the measure, and thus repays the measure with death. Guō comments: "'*Zhì* means 'master.'" Note: If there are indeed names and realities to be disputed, one takes one’s own body as the master, making others follow oneself as the standard of righteousness, and thus jointly defends it to the death—this is the so-called "dying to achieve fame."

In such a case, usefulness is deemed wisdom, and uselessness foolishness; thoroughness is deemed honor, and failure disgrace. In such a case, not only do I not control life and death, but even wisdom and folly, glory and shame are not within my control. If I am raised and employed, then I am deemed worthy and wise; if discarded and unused, then I am deemed mediocre and foolish; if I penetrate and ascend, then I am deemed honorable; if I remain mired and lowly, then I am deemed shameful. This shifting of right and wrong is the way of people today—only power shifts it, and this is the way of people today.

This is like the cicada and the fledgling dove being the same in their sameness. The *Xiāoyáo Yóu* speaks of the cicada and the *xué*-dove laughing at the great Peng—these two creatures are equally ignorant. Are people today not subject to the same ridicule as these two creatures?

If one treads upon the foot of a market-goer, one apologizes with an excuse of recklessness—*Shìwén* notes: "*Niǎn*, *nǚzhǎn* reflex. The *Guǎngyǎ* states: 'to tread.'" Xuān comments: "One apologizes and excuses oneself as reckless to take the blame." If it is an elder brother, one acts with tenderness—Xuān comments: "If one treads upon an elder brother’s foot, one need not apologize or take the blame but merely shows gentle affection." If it is a parent, that is all. Chéng comments: "If a father treads upon a son’s foot, one merely feels concern and says no more." Xuān comments: "One may know that the ultimate Way lies in mutual forgetting."

Thus it is said: The utmost ritual does not enter; Guō comments: "One sees others as oneself." The utmost righteousness does not treat things as objects; Guō comments: "If one attains what is fitting, then all things are oneself." The utmost wisdom does not scheme; Chéng comments: "One follows one’s nature and illuminates." The utmost benevolence has no partiality; Guō comments: "It is like the five viscera—though they do not particularly favor one another, benevolence is already complete." The utmost trust dispenses with gold. Xuān comments: "There is no need to use gold as a guarantee."

Shatter the turbulence of the will; Xuān comments: "*Chè* means 'to destroy.' *Bó* means 'disorder.'" Untie the knots of the heart; Chéng comments: "*Miù* means 'entanglement.'" Cast off the burdens of virtue, and open the blockages of the Way. *Dá* means "to penetrate."

Wealth, honor, prominence, authority, fame, and profit—*yán* means "authority"—these six are the turbulence of the will. Appearance, movement, countenance, speech, *qi*, and intention—these six are the knots of the heart. Appearance, movement, facial expression, words, *qi*, and emotions. Aversion, desire, joy, anger, sorrow, and happiness—these six are the burdens of virtue. Withdrawal, approach, taking, giving, knowledge, and ability—*zhī* means "wisdom"—these six are the blockages of the Way.

When these four sets of six do not agitate the breast, then one is upright. Guō comments: "*Dàng* means 'to move.'" When upright, one is tranquil; when tranquil, one is luminous; when luminous, one is empty; when empty, one does nothing yet nothing is left undone.

The Dao is the reverence of virtue; the Dao is invisible, but when one sees its flowing virtue, all revere it as the conduct of one who embodies the Dao—thus the Dao is the reverence of virtue. Life is the radiance of virtue; Chéng comments: "The great virtue of Heaven and Earth is called life—thus the generation and transformation of the myriad things is the radiant splendor of abundant virtue." Nature is the essence of life; Chéng comments: "Essence means 'root.' The natural nature is the root of the endowment of life." The movement of nature is called *wéi*; Guō comments: "When nature moves of itself, it is thus called *wéi*—this is true *wéi*, not artificial action." Artificial action is called loss. Chéng comments: "Responding to things with movement is the desire of nature. Artificial emotion and action beyond one’s lot is called the loss of the Way."

Knowledge is contact;

Contact with things and knowing them is called knowledge. Knowledge is planning; the character *zhī* (知) is pronounced as *zhì* (智). Planning means strategizing—discerning affairs and deliberating upon them, thus planning reveals wisdom. What the wise do not know is like a sidelong glance. Even the wise have things they do not know, like eyes gazing obliquely in one direction, unable to see all—thus the use of wisdom is partial, not equal to tranquil illumination.

Movement from necessity is called virtue; acting only under compulsion reveals abundant virtue. Movement without self is called order—abandoning the self to pursue things brings chaos, while the reverse brings order. Names oppose one another, yet realities align. Pursuing fame leads to artifice and disorder, ultimately to opposition; seeking reality leads to truth and order, ultimately to harmony.

Yi was skilled at hitting the smallest target yet clumsy at preventing others from praising him. Hitting the smallest target brings praise, for skill and clumsiness are ever intertwined. Only the great Dao can be without name. The sage is skilled in Heaven yet clumsy in man. Guo comments: "Following nature is Heaven; acting with intention is man." Only the complete person can be skilled in Heaven and adept in man. *Shiwen* notes: "*Liáng* (俍) is pronounced *liáng* (良)." Cheng comments: "*Liáng* means 'adept.' The complete person is the spirit-person." Note: The sage refers to those from Yao and Shun onward; the complete person, to those before Fuxi.

Only the creature can be creaturely; only the creature can be Heavenly. Cheng comments: "Birds fly and beasts run—this is being creaturely; spiders weave webs and dung beetles roll balls—this is being Heavenly. All are endowed by the Creator—how could imitation achieve this!" Note: The abilities of creatures are not uniform.

The complete person despises Heaven—despises man’s Heaven, let alone my Heaven or man! The saying that the complete person despises Heaven does not mean despising Heaven itself, but despising man’s Heaven—acting contrary to nature through artifice. If one simply takes man as Heaven and compels all under Heaven to follow, this is even further from the truth.

A sparrow encountered Yi—*shì* (适) means "to meet." Yi was certain to catch it, for he was mighty. Cheng comments: "What he captured was little; what escaped was much. Governing the world with might is likewise." If one takes all under Heaven as a cage, then no sparrow can escape. Cheng comments: "The great Dao is vast and unbounded, encompassing all—thus if one takes all under Heaven as a cage, no sparrow has a place to flee. This shows that seizing things by might deeply contravenes the great Creation."

Thus Tang used a cook to cage Yi Yin, and Duke Mu of Qin used five sheepskins to cage Bai Li Xi. *Bāo* (胞) is the same as *páo* (庖). Yi Yin gained Tang’s favor through butchery and cooking; Bai Li Xi sold himself to a Qin cattle-keeper for five sheepskins, tending oxen to gain Duke Mu’s favor. Both cases were condemned by Mencius. Thus, if one does not use what they desire to cage them, it cannot be done.

The maimed cast aside restraint, heedless of external praise or blame. Guo notes: "介 means amputation." Cui comments: "拸画 means to disregard laws and measures." Yu observes: "The *Hanshu* annotation to the 'Biography of Sima Xiangru' states: *shē* (痑) means 'self-abandonment,' which shares the same sense as *chǐ* (拸) here. The *Guliang Commentary* to the sixth year of Duke Huan reads '以其画我,' while the *Gongyang Commentary* has '化我,' with He’s annotation: 'Excessive conduct without propriety is called *huà* (化).' The meaning of *huà* (画) is likely the same. Once a man’s foot is severed, he no longer cares for self-preservation, and praise or blame become matters of indifference—hence his disregard for laws and measures."

The convict laborer ascends heights without fear, having abandoned life and death. *Xūmí* refers to those sentenced to hard labor. Fu Yue was a *xūmí*. As for "repeated submission without reciprocation," the *Shiwen* notes: "馈 appears as 愧 in the Yuanjia edition." Guo Songtao explains: "The *Shuowen* states: *zhé* (詟) means 'speechless from intimidation,' and *chí* (謵) means 'speech that is *zhé*.' *Fùchí* (复謵) describes others submitting in speech out of deference. To give something to another is called *kuì* (馈); to offer words to another is also called *kuì*. *Bù kuì* (不馈) means not returning thanks." Heedless of external praise or blame, abandoning life and death—this is to forget oneself. "Repeated submission without reciprocation" is to forget others. Guo’s interpretation is closest, corresponding to the later phrase "revered yet unelated." Here, the text may be missing passages, and I dare not force an explanation. To forget others is thus to become one with Heaven and humanity.

To forget others is to become a celestial being, for one thereby approaches the natural. Thus, those who are revered yet unelated, or insulted yet unangered, are those who harmonize with the celestial harmony. Cheng Xuanying notes: "They forget both adversity and favor." Anger that arises from non-anger is anger born of non-anger; though it emerges from what others would find enraging, I do not grow angry, and thus even when anger arises, it still originates from non-anger. This is what Mencius meant by "King Wen’s singular wrath" and "King Wu’s singular wrath." Action that arises from non-action is action born of non-action; though it emerges from where others would act, I do not act, and thus even when action arises, it still originates from non-action. This is what the *Doctrine of the Mean* calls "accomplishing without action," and what Confucius called "governing through non-action." To still the qi, one must first calm it; to attain spiritual clarity, one must first follow the heart. Guo Xiang explains: "When the qi is calm, stillness and principle are complete; when the heart is followed, spiritual efficacy arrives." Yet this is still a form of action. To act appropriately, one must proceed from necessity. Guo Xiang notes: "Proceeding from necessity ensures all actions are fitting." Cheng Xuanying adds: "That which cannot be stopped—responding only when moved—is the proper duty of one’s station." Such necessity is the Way of the sage. Guo Xiang observes: "The sage takes this as the Way—how could he seek non-action beyond the indistinct and evanescent?"

---

In *Miscellaneous Chapters*, "Xu Wugui" (Chapter Twenty-Four):

Xu Wugui, through the minister Nü Shang, was granted an audience with Marquis Wu of Wei. The *Shiwen* notes: "Xu Wugui was a recluse of Wei. Sima Biao’s edition reads: ‘Xu Wugui of Mount Min.’" Cheng Xuanying explains: "Nü is the surname, Shang the given name; he was a minister of Wei." Marquis Wu, named Ji, greeted him, saying: "Master, you must be weary! Having endured the toils of mountain and forest, you have deigned to meet with me." Xu Wugui replied: "I am weary on your behalf—what weariness have you for me? If you indulge your desires and prolong your likes and dislikes, the true nature of your life will sicken. If you suppress your desires and cast off your likes and dislikes, your ears and eyes will sicken. I weary on your behalf—what weariness have you for me?" Marquis Wu was taken aback and made no reply. After a moment, Xu Wugui continued: "Let me tell you something—I read dogs. Those of low quality stop when sated; such is the virtue of the wildcat. Those of middling quality gaze fixedly, as if watching the sun. Those of high quality seem to have forgotten themselves." The *Shiwen* notes: "‘Forgotten themselves’ means their spirit remains unmoved, as if their body no longer exists." "Yet in reading dogs, I am not as skilled as I am in reading horses. In reading horses: the straight align with the plumb line, the curved with the hook, the square with the carpenter’s square, and the round with the compass. These are the horses of the state—what a lord would prize above all. But they do not compare to the horses of the world. The horses of the world possess innate talent, as if anxious, as if lost." The *Shiwen* notes: "‘Lost’ is pronounced *yi*; Sima Biao’s edition reads *yi*. Li Yi explains: ‘*Xu* and *yi* both describe a startled, soaring appearance.’" Cheng Xuanying adds: "Their eyes seem to watch themselves, as if troubled; their hooves move slowly, as if fleeing." "They seem to have forgotten themselves. Such horses surpass all, leaving no trace—one cannot fathom their limits." The *Shiwen* notes: "‘Limits’ refers to where they stop." Marquis Wu was greatly pleased and laughed.

When Xu Wugui departed, Nü Shang asked: "Master, how did you alone delight our lord? When I delight our lord, I expound broadly with the *Odes*, *Documents*, *Rites*, and *Music*, or follow closely with the *Metal Tablets* and *Six Bow-Cases*." The *Shiwen* notes: "Sima Biao and Cui Hao explain: ‘*Metal Tablets* and *Six Bow-Cases* are chapter names in the *Book of Zhou.’* Some say they are esoteric prophecies. The text also reads *Six Strategies*, referring to the *Six Strategies* of the Grand Duke: *Civil*, *Martial*, *Tiger*, *Leopard*, *Dragon*, and *Hound.*’ ‘Tablets’ also reads *boards.*" Cheng Xuanying explains: "‘Broadly’ means distant; ‘closely’ means near. Marquis Wu favored martial arts and disdained culture, so I used military strategy for close persuasion and the Six Classics for distant discourse." "I have served him with great achievements too numerous to count, yet our lord has never once smiled."

"Now, sir, by what means have you persuaded our lord so that he has taken such delight?" Xu Wugui replied: "I merely told him of my skill in appraising dogs and horses." *Merely*—that is, solely.

Nü Shang said: "Is that all?" Cheng Xuanying explains: "He marvels at the shallowness of the method."

Xu Wugui said: "Have you not heard of the exiles of Yue? This is a tale handed down from those days. When they had been away from their homeland for several days, they rejoiced at the sight of those they knew. After a month or so away, they rejoiced at the sight of those they had once seen in their homeland. After a full year, they rejoiced even at the sight of those who merely resembled their countrymen. Does this not show that the longer one is parted from others, the deeper one’s longing for them grows? *The longer*—that is, the more so.

Consider those who flee into the wilderness. Sima Biao explains: "They take ruined tombs as their refuge, dwelling in emptiness." Cheng explains: "This refers to the empty spaces beside abandoned sites, as the text below says, 'they take their place in the emptiness.'" Weeds and brambles stand like pillars along the paths of weasels and badgers—places where only such creatures tread, and where thorns and thickets rise like columns, so desolate and overgrown is the land. *Weeds*—that is, mugwort. The *Erya* says, 'Bai is the *shangdiao*,' and Guo Pu notes: '*Shangdiao* resembles *li* (goosefoot).' Yet when they hear the sound of human footsteps—*qiong*, the sound of walking—they rejoice. How much more, then, would they delight in the voices and laughter of brothers, kin, and dear ones at their side!" Li Yi explains: "*Qingkai* is a metaphor for speech and laughter."

This is to say: just as Marquis Wu’s fondness for dogs and horses made him rejoice at the mere mention of them—no differently than the exile who delights at the sight of a fellow countryman, or the fugitive who thrills at the sound of a kinsman’s voice—how long it has been since the words of a true person have been heard at our lord’s side!

"Yet when true words are spoken, none are brought to our lord’s side.

Xu Wugui presented himself before Marquis Wu, who said: 'Sir, you dwell in the mountains and forests, eating acorns and chestnuts—Guo Qingfan notes: *Xu* (芧) is the oak, also called *xu* (栩), *yu* (●), or *cai* (采). Its fruit is called *zao* (皂) or *xiang* (样). In later texts, *xiang* is written as *xiang* (橡). The characters *xu* (芧), *yu* (●), and *zhu* (杼) are interchangeable. In this chapter, *xu li* (芧栗) appears as *zhu li* (杼栗) in the *Mountain Trees* chapter.' You are sated with scallions and leeks, yet you reject our lord to come as a guest—*bin* here means *ban* (to cast aside). How long it has been! Are you now old? Do you seek the taste of wine and meat? Li [Yi] says: '*Gan* means to seek.' Or does our lord possess some blessing for the altars of soil and grain?' Li [Yi] says: 'This refers to good words and fine counsel that may benefit the altars of soil and grain.'

Xu Wugui replied: 'Wugui was born in poverty and low station and has never dared to partake of your lordship’s wine and meat. I have come to offer you labor.' The Marquis said: 'What is this? How do you labor me?'"

The speaker said: "You labor both your lordship’s spirit and form." Cheng [Xuanying] notes: "When the form is wearied, the spirit grows weary—thus I offer comfort." Marquis Wu asked: "What do you mean?"

Xu Wugui replied: "The nurture of Heaven and Earth is one." Xuan [Ying] notes: "The life Heaven and Earth grant to all people is the same." To ascend high does not make one tall; to dwell low does not make one short. High and low here mean noble and base. Yet you alone, as lord of ten thousand chariots, wear out the people of an entire state to nourish your ears, eyes, nose, and mouth—while the spirit does not consent to this." Xuan [Ying] notes: "The heart-spirit must feel some disquiet." The spirit loves harmony and hates self-interest. Xuan [Ying] notes: "Harmony means unity with things; self-interest is private gain." Self-interest is a sickness, and so it wearies you. Why do you alone suffer from this sickness?"

Xuan [Ying] notes: "Why do you bring this sickness upon yourself?"

Marquis Wu said: "I have long wished to meet you, Master. I wish to love the people and practice righteousness by laying down arms—may this be done?"

Xu Wugui replied: "It cannot. To love the people is the beginning of harming them; though called love, in truth it binds them to service—thus love becomes the start of harm. To practice righteousness by laying down arms is the root of raising arms; though called disarmament, enemy states lie in wait—thus disarmament becomes the root of war. If you proceed thus, you will likely fail. To name a thing and then act otherwise is nearly certain to fail. All attempts to achieve fine reputations are ill-omened tools. Though you act with benevolence and righteousness, will you not become entangled in the trappings of reputation and turn false? Form inevitably gives rise to form; the formless form can create all forms. Yet achievement inevitably invites self-praise; the moment a name is achieved, there is boasting of merit. Change inevitably leads to external conflict; as affairs shift, one daily contends with the outside world. You must not array your troops in crane formation between the towers of the *Li Qiao*, nor station cavalry and infantry in the halls of the *Zi Tan* Palace." Li [Yi] notes: "*Hè liè* means troops arrayed like cranes in formation; *Li Qiao* is the name of a tower." (The halls of the *Li Qiao* and the *Zi Tan* Palace are no place for deploying troops or galloping horses—this is a metaphor urging you not to indulge the mind’s warfare.) Do not hoard what is gained through perversity; let gain come through compliance. Do not overcome others through cunning, nor through schemes, nor through battle. All three are ways of hoarding what is gained through perversity. Those who kill the people’s soldiers and seize others’ lands to nourish their own private ends and their own spirits—nourishing their own bodies and their own heart-spirits—how can their wars be called good? There is no such thing as goodness in them. Where does victory lie? There is no such thing as victory. If you cannot desist, if you persist in this for the sake of the people, then cultivate sincerity within your breast to respond to the feelings of Heaven and Earth, and do not disturb them. If you cultivate your own sincerity to align with Heaven and Earth and refrain from causing disturbance, the people will already have escaped death—what need will you then have to lay down arms?

The Yellow Emperor wished to meet the great spirit *Dai Kui* on Mount Juci. Fang Ming drove the carriage, Chang Yu rode as attendant, Zhang Ruo and Xi Peng led the horses, while Kun Hun and Hua Ji followed in the rear. When they reached the wilds of Xiangcheng, the Seven Sages all lost their way and had nowhere to ask directions. They happened upon a boy herding horses and asked him the way. "Do you know Mount Juci?" they asked. "I do," he replied. "Do you know where the great spirit *Dai Kui* resides?"

The boy said, "I do." The Yellow Emperor said, "How extraordinary, young lad! Not only do you know of Mount Juci, but you also know where the great spirit *Dai Kui* resides. I ask you about governing the world." The boy replied, "Governing the world is no different from this—it is like wandering in the wilds of Xiangcheng and nothing more. What else is there to do? There is no need to seek further affairs.

When I was young, I roamed freely within the Six Directions. Then I happened to suffer from *mào* illness. An elder taught me, saying, 'Ride the chariot of the sun and wander in the wilds of Xiangcheng.' Now that my illness has slightly improved, I am about to roam beyond the Six Directions again. Governing the world is no different from this. What else is there for me to do?"

The boy declined, saying, "Such words are not my concern." The Yellow Emperor said, "Governing the world is indeed not your affair. Nevertheless, I beg to ask about governing the world." The boy declined again. The Yellow Emperor pressed further. The boy said, "What difference is there between governing the world and herding horses? Simply remove what harms the horses—that is all." Seeing what harms the horses and removing it allows the horses to preserve their natural state.

The Yellow Emperor bowed twice, touching his forehead to the ground, and withdrew, calling him "Heavenly Master." He had already met *Dai Kui*.

Scholars find no joy without the vicissitudes of thought, rhetoricians find no joy without the order of discourse, and scrutinizers find no joy without the sharpness of reproach—all are confined by things. They strive to display their abilities, thus bound by the affairs of the world. Those who summon the age to rise in court seek to gather the people and together cross the ford—these are those who labor to exalt their dynasty. The mediocre among men glory in office, content with their rank and guarding their position—these merely take pride in their posts. The strong boast of hardship, their sinews and strength robust, meeting difficulty with arrogance. The brave rush toward peril, their nature fearless, facing calamity with vigor. Warriors delight in battle, long accustomed to arms and armor. Recluses cling to fame, dwelling in mountain forests, attached to lofty renown. Legalists expand governance, studying laws and seeking to broaden their methods. Ritualists refine their demeanor, binding themselves to rites and respecting formal appearances. Moralists value connection, practicing benevolence and righteousness, prizing interaction.

Farmers, without the toil of clearing wild grasses, find no harmony. Merchants, without the bustle of the marketplace, find no harmony. The common folk, when they have morning and evening tasks to share, spur one another on. Artisans, when their tools and machines are skillfully made, feel their spirits rise. Where wealth does not accumulate, the greedy grow anxious; where power does not stand out, the proud grow sorrowful. Those who wield influence and things delight in change, meeting the times with use, unable to remain inactive. Such men, driven by the pursuit of affairs, rejoice in upheaval, finding purpose only when the moment calls—all follow the turning of the seasons, unchanging in their ways, racing their forms and natures, submerged in the ten thousand things, never to return. How lamentable!

Zhuangzi said, "If an archer hits the mark without aiming, is he a good shot?" Huizi said, "He is." Zhuangzi said, "If the world lacks a shared standard of rightness, and each takes his own as right, then is everyone a Yao? Is that acceptable?" Huizi said, "It is." Zhuangzi said, "Then, among the four—Confucians, Mohists, Yangists, and *Bing*—along with you, that makes five. Which of you is truly right? Or is it someone like Lu Ju? His disciples said, 'We have grasped the Master’s Way! We can heat a cauldron in winter and make ice in summer!' Lu Ju said, 'This merely summons yang with yang and yin with yin—this is not what I call the Way. Let me show you my Way.'"

Then he tuned two zithers for them, placing one in the hall and one in the chamber. When he plucked the *gong* note, the *gong* note resonated; when he plucked the *jue* note, the *jue* note resonated—their tones and temperaments were identical.

Xuan remarked, "He used *gong* and *jue* to encompass the five notes. The disciple spoke of qi summoning one another, so Lu Ju demonstrated tones moving one another. *Fei* means 'to place.' One zither was set in the hall, another in the chamber, separated by distance, yet when played, *gong* and *jue* responded in kind, their temperaments undifferentiated. This was Ju’s claim to the Way."

Yet if one alters a single string so that it no longer aligns with the five notes, and plucks it, all twenty-five strings vibrate—no different in sound, yet the ruler of tones is already present.

Xuan remarked, "Zhuangzi refutes Lu Ju’s claim that his Way is extraordinary. Whether considering the two zithers and their five notes resonating, or taking a single zither: when its original tuning is set, each of the five notes has its fixed string. Now, if one alters a single string to create a variation, it no longer aligns with the original five notes and should not respond. Yet when plucked, all twenty-five strings follow and transform, none failing to resonate. How could this involve some sound beyond the five notes? For the five notes can rotate to serve as the *gong*; the altered string becomes the *gong* of the new mode, like a ruler, and the remaining strings naturally follow its movement. Within a single zither, even in a changed mode, all respond without fail—how much more so for two zithers and their five notes, where resonance is but the natural principle? What is so extraordinary here? Ju now boasts of this to his disciples, priding himself on subtle mastery, yet does he recognize that the mutual resonance of tones differs from the mutual summoning of the two qi? Thus, what others deem false, one deems true; what one deems true, others deem false—yet in the end, they are the same. Is this not the case?"

Xuan said, "Hui and the four others each take their own as right, yet in the end there is no universally right—is this not like Lu Ju?"

Master Hui replied, "Now the Confucians, Mohists, Yangists, and Bingists all contend with me in debate, the five schools arguing among themselves. They clash with words, overbear with voices, using speech to scrub one another clean, using reputation to press one another down. Yet none has ever deemed me wrong—how then does it stand?"

Xuan said, "If none of the four schools deems you wrong, what then?"

Guo observed, "'None has ever deemed me wrong' means each takes itself as right. Hui thus wishes to take this as the ultimate."

Zhuangzi said, "There was a man of Qi who maimed his son and sent him to Song to serve as a gatekeeper, his command to the gatekeeper made without regard for wholeness. The man of Qi was unkind, yet he too took himself as right, and so did this. When he sought a *xing* bell, he did so with bindings—fearing its damage, he thus took his love of the bell as right. When he sought his lost child, he never left his own domain, leaving his kind behind. In people’s self-certainty lies such folly!

There was a man of Chu who, lodging elsewhere, berated the gatekeeper. In the dead of night, when no one was about, he fought with the boatman, never leaving the shore’s edge, yet enough to breed resentment."

Guo observed, "The two men of Qi and Chu acted thus, never deeming themselves wrong. How different are the five schools’ self-certainty from this?"

Xuan noted, "*Li* means 'to cling.' In the dead of night, with no one present, the boat had not yet reached shore when he fought the boatman—his life was in peril. Both lodging elsewhere and berating the gatekeeper, and this, were enough to breed resentment."

Zhuangzi, attending a funeral, passed by Master Hui’s grave. Turning to his followers, he said, "A man of Ying daubed his nose-tip with a fly’s-wing-thin smear of white clay and had Carpenter Shi carve it away. Carpenter Shi swung his hatchet with the force of wind, and as he carved, the man of Ying stood unflinching. The clay was gone, yet his nose was unharmed. The Lord of Song Yuan heard of this and summoned Carpenter Shi, saying, 'Try doing this for me.' Carpenter Shi replied, 'Your servant once could carve thus. However, my material has long been dead.' Since the Master’s death, I have had no material, no one with whom to speak."

The Master here referred to Hui. Though Zhuang and Hui differed in conduct, they found deep accord. With Hui’s death, Zhuang had no one with whom to speak freely, and so he sighed.

When Guan Zhong fell ill, Duke Huan asked him, "Uncle Zhong’s illness is grave—one can no longer avoid speaking of it. If it comes to the worst, to whom may I entrust the state?" Guan Zhong replied, "Whom does the Duke wish to entrust it to?"

The Duke said, "Bao Shuya."
Guan Zhong replied, "No. His character is upright and pure, a fine gentleman. Yet he does not associate with those unlike himself; he does not befriend those whose purity does not match his own—his aversion to evil is too severe. Upon once hearing of another’s fault, he never forgets it. If you place him in charge of the state, above he will oppose his lord, and below he will go against the people. It will not be long before he offends you."

The Duke said, "Then who is suitable?"
Guan Zhong answered, "If you must choose, then Xi Peng will do. His character is such that he forgets those above him and does not turn against those below. He feels shame before the Yellow Emperor and pities those who do not match him. One who distributes virtue to others is called a sage; one who distributes wealth to others is called worthy. To rule over others as a worthy is never to win their hearts; to rule while considering oneself worthy is something people will not accept. But to humble oneself as a worthy before others is never to fail in winning their hearts. In governing the state, there are things he does not hear; in managing his household, there are things he does not see. If you must choose, then Xi Peng will do."

The King of Wu floated upon the river and ascended the Mountain of Monkeys. The troop of monkeys, seeing him, in terror abandoned their places and fled, escaping into the dense thickets. Yet one monkey, moving with leisurely grace, leapt and scratched, displaying its skill before the king. The king shot at it, but the monkey nimbly caught the swift arrows. Yu Yue notes: "The words *min* and *ji* share the same meaning. In the *History of the Later Han*, the *Biography of Li Yan* states, 'His speech was *ji* and swift,' with Li’s commentary explaining, '*Ji* means *min*.' Here, *min* and *ji* describe the monkey’s nature—its agility in catching arrows. Earlier annotations mistakenly attribute *min* and *ji* to the king’s archery." The king ordered his attendants to hasten their shots, and the monkey was seized and killed. Turning to his companion Yan Buyi, the king said: "This monkey relied on its skill and trusted in its agility, displaying arrogance toward me—thus it met its end. Take heed! Ah, do not let your demeanor make you proud before others!"

Yan Buyi returned home and studied under Dong Wu to temper his bearing. Guo Xiang notes: "*Zhu* here means 'to remove.'" He abandoned music and declined fame, setting aside pleasures and renouncing prominence. After three years, the people of his state praised him.

Nanbo Ziqi leaned against his armrest and sat, gazing upward as he exhaled slowly. Nanbo, also called Nanguo (with *bo* and *guo* being phonetically interchangeable), appears again in the "Discourse on Equalizing Things," where "armrest" is written as *ji*. Yan Chengzi entered and said: "Master, you are a paragon among beings. How can your form be made like withered bones, your heart like dead ashes?" (In the "Discourse on Equalizing Things," this reads "withered wood," while in the "Gengsang Chu" chapter, it is "the branches of withered wood." Here, "withered bones" is akin to "withered branches." The following text differs.) Ziqi replied: "Once, I dwelled in a mountain cave."

At that time, when Tian He once saw me, the multitudes of Qi thrice celebrated it. (The *Shiwen* notes: "The lord of Qi revered virtue, so the people rejoiced." Lu comments: "Tian He is Qi Taigong He.") I must have preceded him, and thus he knew; I must have sold it, and thus he bought it. My name first became manifest, and only then did he know—this is my selling while he buys. If I did not possess it myself, the name would belong to itself. How could he have known it? If I did not sell it, how could he have bought it?

Alas! I grieve for those who lose themselves (Xuan comments: "Pursuing externals, they lose their true nature."), and I grieve yet more for those who grieve for others (Xuan comments: "They too lose themselves."), and I grieve still further for those who grieve for the grief of others (Xuan comments: "They likewise lose themselves."). Thereafter, day by day, it grew more distant. (Xuan comments: "Only when all worldly concerns are cast aside does one attain the semblance of withered wood and dead ashes.")

When Confucius traveled to Chu, the king of Chu offered him a toast. Sunshu Ao stood holding the goblet, while Shinan Yiliao received the wine and made a libation, saying: "Ah, the men of old!" (Here his words ended.) (The *Shiwen* notes: "In the *Zuozhuan*, Sunshu Ao was the minister of King Zhuang of Chu, before Confucius was born. After Confucius died in the sixteenth year of Duke Ai, the disorder of Duke Bai occurred, and Yiliao never served Chu. Moreover, in the twelfth year of Duke Xuan’s reign, the *Zuozhuan* records a Xiong Xiang Yiliao in Chu, a contemporary of Sunshu Ao, far removed from Confucius. This is likely a figurative reference." Cheng comments: "The ancients always made a libation before drinking. Yiliao poured the wine in offering, thus invoking the sage." Xuan comments: "At this banquet, it was the proper moment to seek the Dao’s principles. Perhaps these two guided Confucius to speak.") Confucius said: "I have heard of the wordless word, yet never before have I spoken of it—until now, when I speak of it.

Shinan Yiliao juggled his pellets and resolved the strife of two families. (Sima comments: "Yiliao was a brave man of Chu, skilled at juggling pellets. When Duke Bai was about to rebel and kill Zixi, Ziqī and Shi Qi said: ‘There is a Xiong Yiliao of Shinan; if we obtain him, he could withstand five hundred men.’ They went to tell him, but he refused. When they pressed him with a sword, he remained unmoved, juggling his pellets as before, and said: ‘I will not betray you.’ Duke Bai then killed Zixi. Ziqī sighed over the strife of the two families, but Yiliao took no part in their troubles." The text says "resolved the strife," which is incorrect; perhaps the records differ.) Sunshu Ao slept peacefully, holding the feathered wand, and the people of Ying laid down their weapons. (Sima comments: "Sunshu Ao slept tranquilly, cultivating virtue in the ancestral temple, yet his influence extended a thousand *li* beyond, deterring enemy states from daring to invade. The people of Ying laid down their weapons, having no one to attack. Ying was the capital of Chu." The *Shiwen* notes: "The feathered wand was held by those performing the *yu* dance." The *Huainanzi*’s "Mastery of Governance" states: "Formerly, Sunshu Ao slept peacefully, and the people of Ying could not employ their blades against others." This aligns with the present text. ["Employ" in Wang’s *Magazine* is corrected to "use."]) I wish I had lips three inches long." (He wished only to possess the means to speak.)

That is what is called the Dao that is not the Dao. (That refers to Yiliao. Sunshu Ao’s resolution of strife and laying down of weapons required no discourse—this is the wordless Dao.) This is what is called the eloquence of the wordless. Thus virtue encompasses the unity of the Dao. (No matter how one practices virtue, the aim is to accord with the Dao—unity alone suffices.) And words rest where knowledge cannot reach—this is perfection. (The highest knowledge. To halt at one’s allotted portion is to reach perfection.) The unity of the Dao cannot be matched by virtue. (Xuan comments: "Those who perceive virtue cannot equal it.") What knowledge cannot know, eloquence cannot exhaust. (Xuan comments: "Those skilled in eloquence cannot exhaust it.") To bear names like Confucian or Mohist is inauspicious. (Xuan comments: "To brandish names is an inauspicious virtue.")

Thus the sea does not refuse the eastward flow—this is the utmost greatness. The sage embraces heaven and earth, bestowing blessings upon the world, yet none know his name. Therefore, in life he holds no rank (Guo comments: "He possesses yet does not possess."), in death he receives no posthumous title (Cheng comments: "In life, he advanced others through merit, so in death, no title is fitting."). He does not accumulate substance (Guo comments: "He allows all things to know contentment."), nor does he establish a name—this is called the Great Person.

A dog is not good because it barks well, nor is a person worthy because he speaks well—how much less so for the great! (Guo comments: "The greater something is, the less it can be attained through effort.") To be great is not sufficient to be called great—how much less so for virtue! (Guo comments: "Only the natural is truly virtue.") The greatest completeness is none other than heaven and earth. Yet what do they seek? They are complete in themselves. Heaven and earth seek nothing, yet lack nothing. One who knows this completeness seeks nothing, loses nothing, discards nothing, and does not exchange the self for things. (Xuan comments: "The self is more precious than things.") Turning inward, he is inexhaustible. (He is naturally inexhaustible.) Following the ancient Way, he does not wear it away. (He follows the ancient Dao without need for polishing.) This is the sincerity of the Great Person. (This is true substance.)

Ziqi had eight sons. Arranging them before him, he summoned Jiufang Yin and said: "Divine for me which of my sons is most auspicious." Jiufang Yin replied: "Kun is the most auspicious." Ziqi started in surprise and joy, asking: "In what way?" He said: "Kun will share the same food as the lord of the state for the rest of his life."

Ziqi’s face fell, and tears streamed down in unbroken flow. "My son," he said, "how could you have come to such an extreme!" Grieving for his misfortune, he wept.

Jiufang Yin replied: "To share the same food as the lord of the state—this grace extends to three generations, how much more to his parents? Yet now you hear this and weep, as if warding off good fortune. Your son is auspicious, but you are not."

Ziqi said: "Yin! How could you understand? What do you mean by calling Kun auspicious? It is nothing more than wine and meat passing through nose and mouth. What do you know of its source? All such things are wrested cruelly from the people.

I have never herded sheep, yet ewes appear in the southwest corner of my house. I have never hunted quail, yet they hatch in the northeast. If these things are not strange, what is?"

"Though you have neither farmed nor herded, ewes and quail still appear—though not taken from the people as a lord of state would, they must still come from some cause. Yet you have never once questioned this strangeness, why is that?

What I share with my son is a wandering through Heaven and Earth—a wandering at ease. With him, I roam in joy beneath Heaven; with him, I roam to eat upon the Earth. *Roam* here means to traverse. The meaning is fully explained in the *Gengsang Chu* chapter, where *roam* is written as *intermingle*. I do not join him in affairs, nor in schemes, nor in marvels. The *Gengsang Chu* chapter is largely the same.

With him, I ride the sincerity of Heaven and Earth without letting things clash against it. This corresponds to the earlier passage: *'Cultivate the sincerity within the breast to respond to the feelings of Heaven and Earth without clashing.'* With him, I drift along in compliance, not binding myself to what affairs deem fitting. I simply move with him in accord, without choosing what is deemed proper for affairs.

All this is the true practice of the Way with my son. Yet now, such worldly recompense has come! You are no worldly man of wine and meat, yet now you have received this worldly repayment of food and drink—how strange!

Whenever strange omens appear, there must be strange conduct behind them." Xuan commented: "This is the usual way of things." Alas!

"Yet it is not the fault of my son or me—it is Heaven that has dealt us this! This is why I weep." Xuan said: "Now there is no strange conduct, yet strange omens appear. It is hardly the fault of my son or me—is Heaven not endangering my house? This is why I weep."

Before long, he was bound and sent to Yan, where bandits seized him on the road. To sell him whole would be difficult; to maim him would be easier. Guo commented: "Keeping him whole risks escape; maiming him makes him easier to sell." Thus they cut off his foot and sold him in Qi, where he happened to serve as a gatekeeper in the streets of Qu Gong. Yet he ate meat to the end of his days. Xuan said: "Qu Gong was likely a fiefdom in Qi, akin to Duke Ye of Chu. Serving as a gatekeeper at the lord’s gate, he thus ate the same food as the lord of the state."

Nie Que met Xu You and asked: "Where are you going?" Xu You replied: "I am fleeing Yao." Nie Que asked: "What do you mean?" Xu You said: "Yao, with his bustling benevolence—Wang commented: *'Bustling' describes his diligent care and toil*—I fear he will become a laughingstock to the world. Might later generations resort to men devouring one another?" The *Shiwen* noted: "This refers to people racing after benevolence and righteousness, neglecting agriculture, and starving to the point of cannibalism." (This passage also appears in the *Gengsang Chu* chapter.)

"Gathering the people is not difficult: love them, and they draw near; benefit them, and they come; praise them, and they are encouraged; impose what they detest, and they scatter. Love and benefit arise from benevolence and righteousness, yet those who abandon benevolence and righteousness are few, while those who exploit them are many. The practice of benevolence and righteousness is fundamentally insincere—Guo commented: *'Once benevolence and righteousness are practiced, they become pretense'*—and is but a tool lent to the rapacious. It is a sharp instrument placed in the hands of beasts of prey." Xuan said: "Like birds of prey coveting gain, akin to the avarice of Robber Zhi."

"Thus, one man’s judgment and control benefit the world—yet it is like a single glance. The *Shiwen* noted: *Sima: 'A fleeting glimpse.' Also read as* fu xie *or* pu jie." Xuan said: "One man’s judgment is limited, like a single blink of the eye—how can it encompass the conditions of all things? Yao knew that worthy men benefit the world, yet he did not know they also harm it. Only those who stand outside worthiness understand this." Xuan said: "Only those who do not exalt worthiness realize how the willful harm the world."

There are those who are *nuan shu*—the *Shiwen* noted: *"Nuan* means soft; *shu*, alluring"—those who are *ru xu*—the *Shiwen* noted: *"Ru xu* means lingering in momentary ease"—and those who are *juan lou*—the *Shiwen* noted: *"Juan lou* means cramped and curled."

The *nuan shu* learn the words of a single master, then preen and delight in private, believing themselves complete. Yet they do not know that nothing has truly taken shape—Cheng commented: "They do not realize that what they have learned amounts to nothing"—hence they are called *nuan shu*.

The *ru xu* are like lice on a pig: they pick through sparse bristles—Cheng commented: "The long, sparse hairs"—and believe themselves in a vast palace or grand park, nestling in the crooks and crevices, between teats and thighs, thinking it a safe and advantageous dwelling. The *Shiwen* noted: *"Kui* is also written as *kui*." Guo Qingfan said: "The crooks and crevices likely refer to the inner thighs. The *Huainanzi*’s *Lanming* commentary states: *'Wei* means the deep, curved places.' The *Zuozhuan*’s Duke Xi 25th Year commentary by Du notes: *'Wei* means hidden, secluded places.' Thus, *wei* refers to inner, deep, and curved recesses." Yet they do not know that one day the butcher will roll up his sleeves, spread straw, and wield fire, and they will be scorched along with the pig.

These advance or retreat only within their domain, their movements confined by boundaries—such are the *ru xu*."

The *juanlou* is Shun. Mutton does not long for ants, but ants long for mutton—mutton is rank. Shun possessed rank conduct, and the common folk delighted in him. Thus, with three relocations he founded cities, and by the time he reached the wilds of Deng, a hundred thousand households had gathered. The *Shiwen* notes: "Xiang said: 'Deng is the name of a settlement.' *Xu* is also written as *xu*." Xiang said: "Barren land is earth without grass or trees." It was hoped that his arrival would bring blessings—it was said they looked forward to Shun’s coming so that he might bestow his grace. Yet when Shun was raised up from barren land, his years had grown long, his keen hearing and sharp sight had faded, and he could not retire and return home. Such was the *juanlou*.

Therefore, the spirit-person loathes the multitude’s arrival. The transcendent spirit-person does not desire the masses to cling to him. When the multitude arrives, he does not draw near; when the multitude comes, he does not form close bonds. Without closeness, there is no advantage. Xuan said: "If he does not form close bonds, others will not see advantage in approaching him." Thus, he has no one excessively close, no one excessively distant. Embracing virtue and warming harmony—The *Shiwen* notes: "Yang: Xu reads *yu liang*; Li said: 'Yang means to roast, to be warmed by the qi of harmony.'"—he accords with the world. This is called the true person.

To ants, he discards cunning; to fish, he finds the proper course; to sheep, he abandons intent. Guo Songtao said: "Ants cling to rankness because they seek advantage—this is their cunning. Sheep’s rankness offers alluring profit—this is their intent. Ants have no cunning yet possess it; sheep have no intent yet possess it. Both should be abandoned. Fish forget one another in rivers and lakes; people forget one another in the Dao and virtue. What rankness is there to long for? Thus it is said: to fish, he finds the proper course."

With the eye, one sees the eye—no outward seeing.
With the ear, one hears the ear—no outward hearing.
With the heart, one returns to the heart—no outward use.
Those who are thus: their equilibrium is as a plumb line—*Cheng* says: "Without heart-mind, yet things are rightly aligned."
Their transformation follows the flow.
"Follows" means to accord.
They move with change and shift.

The true persons of old relied on Heaven to respond—*Cheng* says: "Employing the Dao of spontaneity, emptying the heart-mind to await things."
*Xuan* says: "之 should be 人."
So it is.
They did not let the human enter the Heavenly.

The true persons of old—*Yao* says: "Repeating 'true person' to extol them."
In attaining it, there is life; in losing it, there is death.
In attaining it, there is death; in losing it, there is life.
Attaining spontaneity, there is life; losing spontaneity, there is death.
Attaining external glory, there is death; losing external glory, there is life.

Medicine—its substance is *qin*—*Sima* says: "Monkshood."
Platycodon—it is *jiling*—*Sima* says: "Chicken’s head, also called *qian*."
*Pig’s hooves*—*Sima* says: "Also called *zhuling*."
These are the emperors of their time.
Medicines have rulers and ministers.
These several kinds, observing what is timely and fitting, take turns as ruler.
How could they all be spoken of!

Goujian, with three thousand men in armor and shields, took refuge on Mount Kuaiji.
Only Wen Zhong could know how ruin might be preserved—*Xuan* says: "Clear in statecraft."
Only Wen Zhong did not know why his own person would bring sorrow—blind to self-preservation.

Thus it is said:
The owl’s eyes have their proper use—*Cheng* says: "Suited for night, not for day."
The crane’s legs have their proper measure—severing them brings grief.
To take length as the measure and remove it is to invite sorrow.

Thus it is said:
When the wind passes over the river, there is loss;
when the sun passes over the river, there is loss—*Xuan* says: "Blowing and scorching diminish the water."
Suppose only wind and sun were to guard the river together—
yet the river would deem them as never having disturbed it, for it relies on its source and flows onward.
If wind and sun were to constantly guard the river’s banks,
why would the river consider itself unharmed and undiminished?
Because the river’s source is long and distant, it has something to rely on as it flows.

Thus water guards the earth with certainty,
a shadow guards its person with certainty,
and things guard their kind with certainty—
each abides in its nature, remaining fixed and unmoving.

Therefore, the eye’s reliance on clarity is perilous,
the ear’s reliance on keen hearing is perilous,
the heart’s pursuit of gain is perilous—
their use is subject to exhaustion.

All capacities within the organs are perilous—
whatever the organs possess as capacities is also in danger.
Once peril has taken form, there is no time to change—
unable to turn back, when peril has fully formed, even the wish to alter it comes too late.

Disaster’s growth is ever-concentrated—
the lengthening of misfortune gathers heavily upon the person.
Its reversal depends on effort—
returning to spontaneity relies on the work of power.
Its fruition awaits time—
its resolution in self-reversal also requires long accumulation.

Yet people regard these as their treasures—
people take ears, eyes, heart, and organs as the body’s treasures,
exhausting their use without realizing their daily depletion.
Is this not lamentable?

Thus nations are lost and people are slaughtered without end—
the ruin of states and the slaughter of people continue unbroken through the ages.
Yet none inquire into the cause—
all stem from this failure to examine and question.

Yao said: "This is the source." Thus, when the foot treads upon the earth, though it treads, it relies on what it does not tread to achieve its far-reaching breadth. Treading and stepping are both acts of treading. Breadth means vast and distant. This speaks of the foot gaining the earth to tread upon—though the earth allows its treading, it relies on what remains untrodden to attain its far-reaching vastness.

In human knowledge, what is known is little; though little, it relies on what is unknown to comprehend what Heaven calls the Way. Human knowledge often suffers from its scarcity, yet even in its scarcity, it relies on what remains unknown to grasp the spontaneity of the Heavenly Dao. This unknowing is true knowing.

To know the Great Oneness, to know the Great Yin, to know the Great Vision, to know the Great Equality, to know the Great Square, to know the Great Trust, to know the Great Constancy—this is perfection.

Yao said: "To know these is true knowing—what else is there to say?"

The Great Oneness unifies all; Cheng said: "The Great Oneness is Heaven, which brings forth the myriad things, hence it is called 'unifying.'"

The Great Yin resolves all; Cheng said: "The Great Yin is Earth." Yao said: "It resolves entanglements."

The Great Vision sees all, striving to perceive its vastness.

The Great Equality follows all; Cheng said: "To follow is to accord." Guo said: "Accord with their fundamental nature, allowing each to attain its own, thus achieving Great Equality."

The Great Square embodies all; Guo said: "Embodying them so each attains its proper place—this is why it is called the Great Square."

The Great Trust verifies all; Cheng said: "To verify is to reach. Following and allowing each to attain its reality—this is Great Trust."

The Great Constancy upholds all; Guo said: "When the true is unperturbed, it naturally stabilizes, hence it is upheld by Great Constancy."

All these are of Heaven; Cheng said: "The seven 'Greats' above all arise from spontaneity."

Following brings illumination; Cheng said: "Accord with spontaneity, and wisdom naturally shines forth."

The obscure has its pivot; in the profound obscurity of silence, there is naturally a pivot.

The beginning has its other; within the Great Beginning, the seeds of self and other are already manifest.

Thus, its resolution resembles irresolution; Guo said: "Resolving it requires no effort, hence it seems like irresolution."

Its knowing resembles unknowing; Cheng said: "Able to forget knowledge, hence it seems like unknowing."

Only after unknowing does one attain true knowing.

In questioning, there can be no bounds—inquiring into the Dao has no fixed method.

Yet there can be no lack of bounds—for the Dao inherently has its principles.

Complexity holds reality; Yao said: "Complexity refers to entanglement." Each thing possesses its own inherent principle.

Past and present do not replace one another; Guo said: "Each has its own cause and cannot substitute for another."

Yet they cannot be diminished; Guo said: "Each should fully realize its proper place."

Can this not be called the great elucidation and verification?

Cheng said: "The Dao is vast—how can it not be the illumination of its wondrous principles and the verification of its true discourse?"

Why not inquire into this instead of remaining perplexed?

Yao said: "Why not investigate this principle rather than persist in confusion?"

Yao read the lines "all these are of Heaven," "illumination follows," "the obscure has its pivot," and "the beginning has its other," explaining: "That which is of Heaven follows the constant—through unknowing, one grasps its mystery. Illumination follows the obscure—through knowing, one grasps its subtlety. The former is the essence, hence the obscure has its pivot and the beginning has its other. The latter is the function, hence there is an 'other' as the standard. This standard is not fixed. This is not limited to certain individuals—all people possess it, though those with understanding are few. Yet it cannot be sought through understanding, hence questioning is difficult yet indispensable. This principle is real and not empty—why not inquire into it and remain perplexed for a lifetime?"

To resolve confusion with what is not confused, returning to non-confusion—this is the highest non-confusion. Now, using my own non-confusion to resolve others' confusion, returning to non-confusion—this is the greatest non-confusion.

**Miscellaneous Chapters: Zeyang (Chapter 25)**

Zeyang traveled to Chu. Cheng comments: "His surname was Peng, given name Yang, courtesy name Zeyang; he was a man of Lu." Yi Jie spoke of him to the king, but the king did not grant him an audience, and Yi Jie returned. Cheng comments: "Y

Of those who enter, nine in ten are concealed, leaving but one in ten visible from without—yet even this brings joy and delight. How much more so when one sees what one has always seen and hears what one has always heard?

It is like a towering terrace suspended amidst the crowd—Yu comments: "As if a ten-ren terrace were raised before the eyes and ears of all, so that none fail to see and hear it; its joyous effect would be even more evident."

The Lord of Ran Xiang attained the center of the ring and followed completion—Yu comments: "The *Lu Shi* records a Lord of Ran Xiang in the *Xun Fei Ji*." Guo comments: "Abiding in emptiness to follow things, so that things complete themselves."

With things, there is no end and no beginning, no near and no time—Cheng comments: "No beginning means no past; no end means no future; no near and no time means no present." Note: The character "日" should be read with the preceding phrase.

One who transforms with things is one who does not transform—Guo comments: "Transforming with things, one is ever without self; being ever without self, one is ever unchanging." Note: This passage also appears in the *Zhi Bei You* chapter.

When has one ever abandoned it? (阖同曷) Cheng comments: "Moving with transformation, when has one ever briefly set it aside?"

One who would model oneself on Heaven yet fails to model oneself on Heaven, perishing with things—how then can one make this one’s task? One who desires to follow Heaven’s spontaneity yet ultimately fails, thus perishing with things—how can one conduct oneself in engaging with things?

The sage has never begun with Heaven, never begun with man, never begun with a beginning, never begun with things—Xuan comments: "Such is the absence of intent." Moving with the world without ceasing, what is accomplished is complete yet not ruined—Wang comments: "洫 means ruin." Note: Moving with things without cessation, what is done is complete and unspoiled—this is what is called "non-action that leaves nothing undone."

How does one unite with it? How does one unite with the Dao without intent?

The two questions "how then" urge one to reflect and choose for oneself.

Tang found his steward, Gatekeeper Yin Deng, and made him his tutor—Xuan comments: "Steward and Gatekeeper are official titles." Xiang comments: "Deng Heng was the man’s name." Cheng comments: "Tang forgot things and obtained a worthy minister as his teacher, governing with folded arms without acting." Note: Steward and Gatekeeper were likely two offices; it is suspected that text may be missing after "御," hence Guo’s comment: "He entrusted it to the hundred officials and did not interfere."

Not limited to one teacher, hence below it says "following the teacher without confinement." Following the teacher without confinement, one attains the ability to follow completion—Xuan comments: "Following the teacher without being confined by the teacher, one attains the Way of the ring’s center and following completion."

He made him steward of the name of names—Cheng comments: "Attributing merit to the steward, the name did not rest with himself."

Through effortless adherence to the law, both were made manifest—Cheng comments: "嬴然 means without intent; 见 means manifest." Note: Without reliance on teacher or law, ruler and minister both became illustrious—this is what is called "illuminating through one’s lord."

Confucius exhausted his thoughts and became his tutor—Guo comments: "Confucius said, ‘What thought, what reflection does the world require?’ His thoughts were already exhausted." Xuan comments: "One should take Confucius as a teacher and be transformed by him."

The Master of Rong Cheng said: "Remove the days and there are no years." Guo comments: "The reason we now have years and preserve days is because of life and death. If there were no death and no life, then the reckoning of years and days would be abolished." Note: The *Huainanzi*'s *Benjing Xun* glosses: "The Master of Rong Cheng was the maker of the calendar in the time of the Yellow Emperor." "There is no inner, no outer." Cheng comments: "The inner is the self; the outer is things. Because we reckon life and death, there is inner and outer. Once years and days are dispensed with, where would self and things be applied?" Yao comments: "Remove the days and there are no years—this is accumulating the few to make the many. There is no inner, no outer—this is accumulating the subtle to make the manifest. These are the maxims of the ancients."

Wei Ying made a pact with Marquis Tian Mou. Sima comments: "Ying was King Hui of Wei; Mou was King Wei of Qi." Marquis Tian Mou broke the pact. Wei Ying was enraged and was about to send an assassin after him. The Marshal of the Rhinoceros heard of it and was ashamed, saying: "You are a lord of ten thousand chariots, yet you would follow a commoner in seeking vengeance!" The Marshal of the Rhinoceros—*Shiwen* gloss: "An official title of Wei. Sima comments: 'Like the Tiger’s Fang General of today; Gongsun Yan held this office.'" Yan requested to receive two hundred thousand armored troops to attack Qi on his lord’s behalf, capture its people, lead away its cattle and horses, and make its lord’s heart burn with rage until it erupted on his back, then seize his state. Ignoring this, Tian Ji fled—this was Tian Ji. Only then did he strike his back and break his spine.

""*Shiwen* gloss: "'Chì' means 'to strike.'" Jizi heard of this and was ashamed, saying—*Shiwen* gloss: "Jizi, a minister of Wei"—"To build a wall seventy feet high, only to tear it down once it reaches seventy feet—this is the toil that convict laborers lament. Now, for seven years, no war has arisen; this is the foundation of Your Majesty’s rule. Yan is a disturber of order and must not be heeded."

Xuan comments: "Even convict laborers regret the destruction of a wall they have built, yet the Rhinoceros’ Marshal would overturn the very foundation of kingship. Such a man is a disturber of order."

Huazi heard of this and found it disgraceful, saying—*Shiwen* gloss: "Huazi, also a minister of Wei"—"He who speaks well of attacking Qi is a disturber of order; he who speaks well of not attacking Qi is also a disturber of order. To declare that those who advocate attack or non-attack are disturbers of order—this, too, is the speech of a disturber of order." *Cheng* comments: "These are Huazi’s own words."

Xuan comments: "He has not yet freed his mind from entanglement in affairs."

The king said, "Then what should be done?"

Huazi replied, "Your Majesty need only seek the Dao."

Xuan comments: "The Dao is of the same substance as the Great Void. Even the enterprise of kingship is not worth mentioning—how much less the venting of anger!"

Huizi heard this and went to introduce Dai Jinren.

*Shiwen* gloss: "Jinren, a worthy of Liang; Huishi recommended him to the king of Wei."

Dai Jinren said, "There is a creature called the snail—does Your Majesty know of it?"

*Shiwen* gloss: "‘Snail’ is pronounced *guā*. Li comments: ‘It has two horns and is commonly called the snail-ox.’ *Sancang* states: ‘A small ox-shaped snail, popularly named the yellow calf.’"

The king said, "Yes."

"There is a kingdom on the left horn of the snail called the Chus, and a kingdom on the right horn called the Mans. From time to time, they contend over territory and wage war, leaving tens of thousands of corpses strewn upon the field. The victors pursue the routed for fifteen days before returning."

The king said, "Alas! Is this mere empty talk?"

Dai Jinren said, "Allow your servant to make this real for Your Majesty. When Your Majesty directs your mind to the four directions, above and below—are they bounded?"

*Suyu* notes: "‘Directs’ here means ‘examines.’"

The king said, "They are unbounded."

Dai said, "To know the mind roams in the unbounded, yet returns to the realm of common access—does it exist or not?"

*Guo* comments: "Where human traces reach is ‘common access,’ meaning the present world within the four seas."

*Cheng* comments: "In terms of its magnitude, it may be said to be as if present, as if absent."

The king said, "So it is."

Dai said, "Within the realm of common access lies Wei; within Wei lies Liang."

*Cheng* comments: "Formerly in Hedong, the state was called Wei. Pressed by Qin, it moved its capital to Liang."

"Within Liang lies Your Majesty. Between Your Majesty and the Mans—is there any distinction?"

The king said, "There is no distinction."

The guest departed, and the king was left in a daze, as if something had been lost.

*Shiwen* gloss: "‘Daze’ means ‘bewildered.’"

As if something were missing.

When the guest departed, Huizi came to see the king.

The preceding "guest departed" and these two characters "guest departed" are likely superfluous.

The king said, "That guest is a great man—even a sage cannot compare to him."

*Cheng* comments: "What Jinren discussed was vast and profound; the conduct of sages like Yao and Shun would not suffice to match him."

Huizi said, "When one blows into a *guan*-pipe, there is a resounding note."

*Shiwen* gloss: "*Xiao*, pronounced *hūxiāo* (許交反), is the sound of the pipe."

"But when one blows into the ring of a sword hilt, there is only a faint *xuè*."

*Shiwen* gloss: "*Xuè*, pronounced *xuè* (血) or *xuè* (呼悅反). Sima comments: ‘The ring of a sword hilt refers to the small hole in the pommel. The sound is like a gust of wind passing—*xuè*.’"

"Yao and Shun are what people praise; yet to speak of Yao and Shun before Dai Jinren is like that faint *xuè*."

When Confucius traveled to Chu, he lodged at the thatched hut of an ant-hill wine-seller.

*Li* comments: "Ant-hill is the name of a mountain. A family that sells wine."

His neighbors—a husband and wife, master and servant—climbed to the ridgepole.

*Sima* comments: "The ridgepole is the roof beam. They ascended to look out."

Zilu said, "What is this bustling crowd doing?"

The crowd is gathered thus—what are they doing?"

*Shiwen* gloss: "*Zōng*, also written as 总."

Cheng comments: "A multitude assembled."

Zhongni said, "They are the servants of a sage.

Cheng comments: "In ancient times, the virtuous and the noble were all called sages; thus Confucius refers to Yiliao of Shinan as a sage. He means that this bustling crowd are the retainers of Yiliao of Shinan."

They bury themselves among the people,

Guo comments: "They dwell among them as equals."

they conceal themselves in the fields.

Wang comments: "They hide in the furrows at the edges of the fields."

Their voices fade, yet their aspirations are boundless, their minds set upon the great Dao. Though their mouths may speak, their hearts never truly utter a word, ever serene and still. Even as they stand apart from the world, their minds disdain to keep it company.

Cheng comments: "Both their hearts and their conduct are at odds with it."

They are those who "sink on dry land,"

Xuan comments: "They drown themselves where no water is."

are they not Yiliao of Shinan?"

Zilu asked to go and summon him.

Confucius said, "Enough! He knows that I am aware of him,

Cheng comments: "*Zhuó*: to recognize clearly."

he knows that I am traveling to Chu, and takes me for one who will surely persuade the King of Chu to summon him. He would regard me as a flatterer. One who feels thus about flatterers would feel shame even to hear their words—how much more to meet them face to face! He will certainly not come. And how can you assume he still exists?"

The Xuan commentary states: "He would surely flee and depart."

Zilu went to see him, and his dwelling was empty.

The Warden of Changwu asked Zilao, *Shiwen* notes: "Changwu is a place name. The Warden is the one who guards the border. Sima Biao says: 'Zilao is Qin Lao, a disciple of Confucius.'" "When governing as a ruler, do not act recklessly; when ordering the people, do not act carelessly." Sima Biao says: "Recklessness is like coarse plowing and sparse sowing. Carelessness is like cutting down the grass." Lu comments: "*Cū* is read as *qiān nú*; *cū* as *cái gǔ*. These two characters were often used together in ancient times. The *Fanlu* in the *Yu Sequence* states: 'Beginning with the coarse, ending with the refined.' The *Lunheng* in the *Zhengshuo* chapter says: 'Briefly setting forth the coarse outlines to illuminate the subtle and wondrous text within.' There are many other instances where *cūcū* is used together, meaning coarse and rough. Some wish to emend it to *cūshū* (careless), but I correct it here."

"In the past, when I cultivated grain, if I plowed recklessly, the harvest would repay me in kind; if I weeded carelessly, the harvest would repay me in kind. The next year, I changed my methods—deepening the plowing and carefully tending the soil." Sima Biao says: "*Yōu* means to hoe." "The grain flourished and multiplied, and I had my fill of food for the entire year." *Yàn* means sufficiency.

Zhuangzi heard this and said: "Nowadays, when people cultivate their bodies and order their minds, many resemble what the Warden described: they flee from their Heaven, depart from their nature, extinguish their emotions, and lose their spirit, all for the sake of busyness. There is nothing they do not pursue. Thus, those who recklessly harm their nature—desires and aversions sprout like calamities, turning their nature into reeds and rushes." *Note*: This means that what they desire and what they detest grow rampantly, injuring their true nature, which becomes like reeds and rushes.

"At first, these sproutings seem to support my form, but soon they uproot my nature." Yu comments: "*Xún* and *shǐ* are opposites in meaning.
