The *Hanshu* in the *Jiaosi Zhi* states: 'It gradually extended to Mount Tai,' and Jin Zhuo notes: '*Xún* means to proceed further.' This means that the affairs of desire and aversion, at first sprouting, seem to assist my form, but as time passes, they seduce my mind and uproot my nature."

"They burst forth in chaotic dissolution, erupting without choosing where to emerge." *Bìngkuì* means to collapse in disarray. *Lòufā* means to break through and emerge. This describes how the harm of emotions and desires collapses and erupts everywhere, without regard for place, so that the spirit is first ruined, and the form and *qi* follow.

"Boils, sores, scabies, and ulcers arise, as do internal heat and turbid urine." *Shiwen* notes: "*Piāo* is also written as *biāo*. *Biāojū* refers to festering sores. *Sōugāo* refers to the fatty white foam that forms on the urine of those suffering from consumptive exhaustion."

Boju studied under Lao Dan. *Shiwen* notes: "Boju was a man of the Dao."

Boju said, "I beg to wander through the world." Lao Dan replied, "Enough! The world is all like this." Boju asked again, and Lao Dan said, "Where will you begin?" Boju answered, "I will begin in Qi."

When he arrived in Qi, he saw a man who had been executed. He pushed the body to straighten it, removed his court robes to cover it, and wailed to Heaven, saying, "You! You! The world suffers great calamity, yet you alone have met it first!" *Great calamity* refers to being killed.

He cried, "Do not steal! Do not kill!" Xuan comments: "He also said, 'Was it not for stealing? Was it not for killing?'" When honor and disgrace are established, then one sees what brings suffering. Guo comments: "If each attains what is proper, there is no honor or disgrace. But when gain and loss are tangled, honor and disgrace arise. Once they arise, one exalts what is called disgrace and strives for what is called honor. Racing between exaltation and striving—how is this not suffering?" When wealth and goods are amassed, then one sees what brings strife. Guo comments: "If one takes contentment as wealth, what would there be to strive for?"

Now, establishing what brings people suffering, amassing what brings them strife, exhausting their strength without respite—how could one avoid reaching this point? Guo comments: "When superiors have their preferences, inferiors cannot rest in their proper places."

The rulers of old took success as belonging to the people and failure as their own; they took uprightness as belonging to the people and wrongdoing as their own. Cheng comments: "They drew blame to themselves." Thus, if a single person lost their proper form, they would withdraw and take responsibility. *A single person* refers to any individual. Cheng comments: "If a single thing lost its place, harming its form and nature, they would take responsibility, as when Tang of Yin cut his hair and nails to bring relief from drought for a thousand *li*."

Now it is not so. They conceal their actions and deem others foolish for not recognizing them, hiding what they do and blaming those who do not understand these things as ignorant. They create great difficulties and punish those who dare not act; they impose heavy burdens and penalize those who cannot bear them. Xuan comments: "They overburden with tasks and punish those who cannot fulfill them." They set distant paths and condemn those who do not arrive. Xuan comments: "They lengthen the journey and punish those who do not reach the end."

When the people know their strength is exhausted, they resort to deception to continue. Guo comments: "To avoid punishment and penalties." Deception increases daily—how can the scholars and common people not be false? Xuan comments: "For the inferiors imitate their superiors."

When strength is insufficient, there is deception; when wisdom is insufficient, there is deceit; when wealth is insufficient, there is theft. Whom can one blame for the acts of theft and robbery?" Guo comments: "One should blame those above."

Qū Bóyù, at sixty years of age, underwent sixty transformations. Xuan comments: "He was not confined by the past." There was never a time when he began with what was right only to end by condemning it as wrong. Cheng comments: "Within a single year, what is right is not constant; thus what was right at the beginning may be condemned as wrong by the end." How could one know that what is deemed right today is not what was deemed wrong in the fifty-nine years prior? This echoes the "Parables" chapter, where Confucius speaks similarly.

All things possess life, yet none see their root; all things emerge, yet none see their gate. People all honor the knowledge of what they know, but none know to rely on the knowledge of what they do not know before attaining true knowledge—can this not be called a great delusion? The first two instances of *zhi* are pronounced as "wisdom"; the last is read as the character itself. Guo comments: "What I do not know, other things may know. Thus, employing the knowledge of things, there is nothing one cannot know; relying solely on my own knowledge, one knows very little. Now, if one does not rely on things for knowledge but instead exalts one’s own wisdom, then things will not inform me—how can this not be a great delusion?" Xuan comments: "The knowledge of what one does not know refers to the 'unseen' mentioned above."

Enough! Enough! There is no escape from this. Xuan comments: "Who in ancient or modern times could escape this principle of the unknown?" Is this what is meant by "so it is," or is it not so? The *Exegesis* states: "The phrase 'is it so?' suggests it is not yet so." This aligns with the meaning in the *Analects*: "So it is—yet is it truly so?"

Confucius asked the Grand Historian Dàtāo, Bó Chángqiān, and Xī Wéi—the three were officials of the archives—saying: "Duke Ling of Wei indulged in wine and revelry, neglecting the governance of the state; he devoted himself to hunting and archery, ignoring his duties among the feudal lords."

"Sīmǎ comments: 'Jì refers to matters of alliances and assemblies.'" Why then was he called "Líng Gōng"? Guō comments: "Líng has two meanings."

Dàtāo said: "It is because of this very reason." Chéng comments: "To be disordered yet unharmed is called *líng*; it is a posthumous title for one without the Way, hence 'it is because of this very reason.'"

Bó Chángqiān said: "Duke Líng had three wives, with whom he bathed together in the same vessel." *Exegesis*: "Làn refers to a bathing vessel." The historian presented the ritual implements and advanced to the proper place. The Duke clasped the silk and supported him with reverence. Chéng comments: "Upon seeing Shǐ Yú, the Duke was deeply ashamed and remorseful; he dispatched attendants to grasp the silk, commanding them to assist and escort him with deference, sending him off with consolation." His arrogance was extreme as seen there, yet his reverence before the worthy was as solemn as this—this is why he was called Líng Gōng." Chéng comments: "Moreover, the rules for posthumous titles state: 'One whose virtue is pure and luminous is called *líng*.'"

Xī Wéi said: "When Duke Líng died, divination for burial in the ancestral tomb proved inauspicious, while divination for burial at Shāqiū was auspicious. After digging several *rèn* deep, they found a stone outer coffin. Washing and inspecting it, they saw an inscription that read: 'Not relying on his descendants, Líng Gōng seized and dwelled here.'" *Exegesis*: "Lǐ means dwelling." Guō Sōngtāo comments: "Among the ancients who were buried, if descendants lacked the ability to rely upon and protect the tomb, Líng Gōng took and occupied it." That Líng Gōng was *líng* had long been so—how could those two men have understood it?" Sū Yú comments: "Xī Wéi attributes it to predetermination, the origin of those who speak of fate and the numinous."

Shàozhī asked Dàgōng Tiáo: "What is meant by 'the words of the village and neighborhood'?"

Dàgōng Tiáo replied: "'Village and neighborhood' refers to the blending of a hundred names from ten clans to form customs. Uniting the disparate as one—Guō Xiàng notes: 'Uniting the ten and hundred as village and neighborhood'—and dispersing the one into the disparate—Guō Xiàng notes: 'Dispersing village and neighborhood into ten and hundred.' Now, pointing to the hundred parts of a horse, one does not obtain the horse; yet when the horse stands before one, its hundred parts are called the horse—Guō Xiàng notes: 'Only by uniting the disparate as one can the Dao be seen. The principles of the world are all thus.'

Thus, hills accumulate the low to become high; rivers gather waters to become great—Yú Yuē notes: '*Waters* is an error for *small*. High and low, small and great, form parallel phrases.' The great person unites and merges to become impartial—Guō Xiàng notes: 'Without partiality toward the world, the customs of the world become one.' Therefore, what enters from without has a master but is not rigidly held—Guō Xiàng notes: 'The mind is the great root of the world; thus what enters from without has a residing master but no bias.' What issues from within has rectitude but does not resist—Guō Xiàng notes: 'Conduct is the world’s universal Dao; thus what issues from within attains right principle and things cannot oppose it.'—Note: *Rectitude* appears as *matching* in the *Tiānyùn* chapter commentary.

The four seasons have distinct *qi*, yet Heaven does not bestow them, so the year is completed—Guō Xiàng notes: 'Bestowal would be partial.' The five offices have distinct duties, yet the ruler does not favor them, so the state is well-governed—Guō Xiàng notes: 'Distinct duties naturally possess their own talents; thus they are entrusted, not favored.' The civil and martial in the great person are not bestowed, so their virtue is complete—Guō Xiàng notes: 'The civil is naturally civil, the martial naturally martial; they are not bestowed by the great person. If they arose from bestowal, they would at times be lacking. Not only the civil and martial—all natures are thus.'—Note: Guō Xiàng’s edition includes *distinct talents* after *martial*, but the text appears incomplete. Later editions, including the *Shìwén* and *Chéng* commentary, omit this, likely a later interpolation.

The myriad things have distinct principles; the Dao is not partial, so it is nameless—Guō Xiàng notes: 'The Dao is undifferentiated; it cannot be named.' Being nameless, it is non-action; being non-action, it is yet without non-action—Guō Xiàng notes: 'Names are limited by reality, hence non-action; realities act of themselves, hence without non-action.' Times have endings and beginnings; the world has changes. Fortune and misfortune flow on—Wáng notes: '*Flow on* describes their movement.'—where there is obstruction, there is also what is fitting—Guō Xiàng notes: 'Fortune and misfortune are intermingled, naturally interdependent; within disappointment lies what is favorable.'

Pursuing distinct faces—Chéng notes: '*Pursuing* means following; *faces* means directions. Right and wrong, each clinging stubbornly, thus each follows its own view and their directions differ.'—where there is rectification, there is also deviation—Guō Sōngtāo notes: 'Forcing divergence to be called rectification is in fact mutual deviation.'

Compare it to a great marsh: all hundred timbers are measured—all trees drifting downstream are measured by the water’s edge; none are discarded. Compare it to a great mountain: trees and stones share the same foundation—Chéng notes: '*Foundation* means base.' This is what is meant by 'the words of the village and neighborhood.'"

Shàozhī said: "Then is calling it the Dao sufficient?"

Dàgōng Tiáo replied: "No. Now, when counting things, their number is not limited to ten thousand, yet we designate them as 'the ten thousand things'—Chéng notes: '*Designate* means limit.'—calling them by the number of the many—Lǐ notes: '*Calling* means speaking.' Thus, Heaven and Earth are the greatest of forms; yīn and yáng are the greatest of *qi*; the Dao is their impartiality—Guō Xiàng notes: 'The Dao is what Heaven and Earth, yīn and yáng share in common. It cannot be pointed to as form, nor as *qi*; its greatness is unparalleled.' Designating it by its greatness to call it so may be permissible—Guō Xiàng notes: 'Just as the ten thousand things cannot be counted and are roughly designated for convenience, the Dao’s greatness cannot be pointed to, so it is roughly designated by the word *Dao*. How could there truly be one thing or affair that can be named the Dao?' Yet once it is named, how can it be compared? If you argue thus, it is like dogs and horses—far from reaching the Dao—Guō Xiàng notes: 'If you say "call it the Dao," then the Dao is like naming a dog as dog, a horse as horse, equating it with a single thing—far from the Dao.'"

Shàozhī said: "Within the four directions, within the six conjunctions, from whence do the ten thousand things arise?"

Dàgōng Tiáo replied: "Yīn and yáng illuminate, cover, and govern one another—Yú Yuē notes: '*Cover* should be read as *harm*. The *Shìyán* states: "*Cover* means to sever." The *Shìwén* notes: "*Cover* in Shèrén’s edition is *harm*." Thus, yīn and yáng sometimes harm, sometimes govern one another, as below: "The four seasons give way to, generate, and destroy one another." Desire and aversion, approach and withdrawal, arise therefrom—Guō Xiàng notes: "*Arise* means to emerge."—Chéng notes: "*Arise* describes their appearance." Male and female unite in their ordinary pairing."

"Lu's *Exegesis* notes: '*Piàn* is pronounced *pàn*.' Chéng comments: '*Yōng* means constant.' Danger and safety alternate, fortune and misfortune arise from one another, urgency and delay rub against each other, gathering and scattering thus take form. Urgency and delay refer to long life and early death; gathering and scattering refer to life and death. These are the principles of names and realities that can be recorded, the subtleties that can be inscribed. Following the sequence, they mutually order one another; in their cyclical movement, they mutually employ one another—Chéng comments: 'The four sequences follow one another, alternately governing and ordering; the Five Phases move in motion, successively driving one another.' Exhaustion returns to the beginning, ending reverts to the start. These are the attributes of things, the limits of speech, the extent of knowledge—merely the utmost of visible things. Those who perceive the Dao do not follow what is abandoned, nor trace what arises—Xuān comments: 'Knowing it has no origin, they accord with its spontaneity. *Suí* means to pursue.' This is where discussion halts." Xuān comments: "How can one rashly claim to know whence the ten thousand things arise!"

Shàozhī said: "Jì Zhēn’s doctrine of *non-action* and Jiēzǐ’s doctrine of *some agency*—which of these two theories accords with reality? Which is partial in its reasoning?" Chéng comments: "Jì Zhēn and Jiēzǐ were worthy men of Qí, both frequenting the Jìxià academy. *Mò* means *without*; *shǐ* means *to act*. Jì Zhēn took non-action as the Dao, while Jiēzǐ held that the Dao has the power to act upon things. Each adhered to one school, neither offering a comprehensive discourse, hence the question to determine their merits." Yú comments: "The *Lǐjì* *Jìyì* commentary by Zhèng and the *Mèngzǐ* *Gōngsūn Chǒu* commentary by Zhào both state: '*Huò* means *to have*.' Here, *huò* stands opposite *mò*. *Mò* is *without*; *huò* is *to have*. The *Yìjīng* *Běn Guà* hexagram, line nine, reads: '*No one benefits it, but someone strikes it*,' also opposing *mò* and *huò*." Guō Qìngfān comments: "Jiēzǐ appears in the *Hànshū* *Rénbiǎo* as *Jiézǐ*. *Jiē* and *jié* are interchangeable characters. The *Shǐjì* *Mèngzǐ Xúnqīng Lièzhuàn* *Suǒyǐn* states: '*Jiēzǐ* is the name or title of an ancient writer.'"

Dàgōng Tiáo replied: "The crowing of roosters and the barking of dogs—these are things people know. Even those of great wisdom share this knowledge. Yet they cannot articulate the source of their spontaneous transformation—Xuān comments: 'If one were to examine the cause of a single crow or bark, the reason for their natural occurrence, even the wise could not explain the wonder of their self-transformation.' Nor can they fathom what they will do next—Xuān comments: 'Nor can they anticipate the mechanism of what they are about to do.' Dissecting these things—Xuān comments: '*Sī* means to divide. The *Shījīng* states: "*An axe is used to split it*."'—their subtlety reaches the incomparable, their magnitude extends beyond containment. *Jīng* means fine; *lún* means comparison—Xuān comments: 'Even in the cries of tiny creatures, we cannot clarify their reasons, so how can one speak, measure, or conjecture about things so minute they cannot be broken down, so vast they cannot be encompassed?' Agency or non-action, neither escapes entanglement with things, and thus both err in their claims. Agency implies solidity—Chéng comments: 'Adhering to action.'—while non-action implies emptiness—Chéng comments: 'Drowning in causelessness.' Having name and reality, they reside in things—Xuān comments: 'Affirming reality, they remain within the realm of things. This is the error of the *some agency* doctrine.' Having no name and no reality, they dwell in the void of things—Xuān comments: 'Affirming emptiness, they become entirely hollow. This is the error of the *non-action* doctrine.' What can be spoken or conceived only distances one further from the truth. Believing it can be expressed in words or grasped in thought, one only drifts further from the Dao. What has not yet arisen cannot be prevented; things that have not yet come into being cannot be forbidden from arising. What has already died cannot be obstructed—Lu’s *Exegesis* notes: 'The text also reads *cú*.'—for once dead, it cannot be impeded from passing away. Life and death are not distant, yet their principle remains unseen. Life and death lie before us, yet their principle eludes perception. Agency or non-action—these are the pretexts for doubt. The two doctrines serve as the borrowed grounds for later skeptics. Observing their root, their past is boundless; seeking their end, their future knows no limit—Xuān comments: 'To trace their beginning is to find the past without end, its origin unknowable; to trace their end is to find the future without limit, its conclusion unknowable.' Boundless and limitless, speech is also without end, sharing the same principle as things—Guō comments: 'The principle of things is boundless, thus knowing speech is boundless allows one to share the same principle as things.' Agency and non-action are the foundation of speech, ever accompanying things from beginning to end. Whether one speaks of agency or non-action, these two doctrines form the basis of discourse, yet they remain mired in things from start to finish. The Dao cannot be affirmed as existent, nor can existence be affirmed as non-existent—Chéng comments: 'The supreme Dao is unceasing, neither existent nor non-existent. Thus, clinging to existence or non-existence, both are untenable.' The name *Dao* is a borrowed term for its function—Guō comments: 'It is that by which things proceed, hence the borrowed name *Dao*.' Agency or non-action—these are but partial aspects of things. How can they suffice for the great square? If words sufficed, then speaking all day would exhaust the Dao—Guō comments: 'Seeking the Dao beyond words and thoughts, then words suffice.' If words did not suffice, then speaking all day would only exhaust things—Guō comments: 'Unable to forget words and preserve meaning, then words do not suffice.' The utmost of the Dao and things cannot be borne by speech or silence. The extremes of the Dao and things cannot be conveyed by words or silence. Neither speech nor silence—this is the discussion of their limits."

Master Xuan comments: "To seek the Dao beyond speech and silence—this is the ultimate discourse."

---

**Miscellaneous Chapters: External Things (Chapter Twenty-Six)**

External things cannot be relied upon. Whatever comes from without—its benefits and harms are equally uncertain. Thus, Long Feng was executed, Bi Gan was slain, and Jizi feigned madness—Master Xuan comments: "Goodness cannot be forced." While E Lai died, and Jie and Zhou perished—Master Xuan comments: "Evil cannot be indulged." No ruler fails to desire loyalty from his ministers, yet loyalty does not always earn trust. Thus, Wu Yun was cast into the Yangtze—Master Cheng comments: "He remonstrated with Fuchai, who killed him, flayed his skin to make a bag shaped like an owl, filled it with his corpse, and set it adrift upon the river." Chang Hong died in Shu, his blood preserved for three years before transforming into jade—Master Cheng comments: "Exiled to Shu, he resented his loyalty being slandered and disemboweled himself. The people of Shu, moved by his fate, stored his blood in a casket, and after three years, it turned into jasper." (The *Shiwen* notes: "See the *Lüshi Chunqiu*.") No parent fails to desire filial piety from their children, yet filial piety does not always earn love. Thus, Xiaoji grieved to death, and Zeng Shen lived in sorrow—Master Cheng comments: "Xiaoji, son of Gaozong of Yin, suffered under his stepmother’s cruelty and died of distress. Zeng Shen, the paragon of filial piety, was hated by his parents and often beaten near to death, hence his lamentations." Li comments: "Zeng Shen, though supremely filial, was despised by his father and frequently left to starve before reviving."

When wood rubs against wood, it ignites—Yu comments: "The *Huainanzi’s* *Yuandao Xun* also states: 'When two pieces of wood rub together, they catch fire.' Yet mere wood rubbing seldom ignites. The following line reads 'metal and fire,' suggesting this should also read 'wood and fire.' The subsequent text frequently mentions fire, further confirming this emendation. Since both metal and wood fear fire, the passage uses them to illustrate fire’s great destructive power." When metal and fire are confined together, the metal melts. When yin and yang clash, heaven and earth convulse—*Shiwen* notes: "骇 (*hài*) means 'to stir.'" Master Xuan comments: "骇 means 'to move.'" Then thunder and lightning arise, fire appears in water, and great locust trees are consumed—Sima comments: "Fire in water refers to lightning. 'Consumed' describes how thunderbolts burn great trees." In such times, one faces extreme distress, trapped between two perils with no escape. People, too, suffer extreme distress—benefit and harm are its cause. Harm is indeed harmful, but benefit is also harmful. Thus, one is constantly ensnared, with no escape.

Anxiety and fear yield no success—*Shiwen* notes: "螴蜳 (*chén dūn*), Guo reads as *chén dūn*." Master Cheng comments: "It means 'apprehension and dread.'" (Note: This refers to those who overvalue external things; though anxious and fearful, they achieve nothing.) The heart, suspended between heaven and earth—*Shiwen* notes: "县 (*xuán*) is read as *xuán*."—strains toward external things, extending to the limits of the cosmos. Now soothed, now agitated, now sunk, now mired—Li comments: "睯 (*mǐn*) means 'oppressed.'" As benefit and harm grind against one another, they generate vast fires, just as external things do—Guo comments: "This is due to internal heat." The multitude burns their harmony. All are drowned in benefit and harm, thus scorching the primordial qi of harmony within their hearts.

The moon is no match for fire. The clarity of the human heart is like the moon—how can it withstand such fire? Thus, one collapses, and the Dao is exhausted—*Shiwen* notes: "僓 (*tuí*) is read as *tuí*." Master Xuan comments: "Thus, one crumbles and decays, the heavenly principle perishes, and the vitality of life is extinguished."

Zhuang Zhou was poor, so he went to borrow grain from the Marquis of the River. (The *Shiwen* notes: "The *Shuo Yuan* records this as the Marquis of Wei.") The Marquis replied: "Very well. When I receive the taxes from my fief, I will lend you three hundred pieces of gold. Will that suffice?" Master Cheng comments: "He meant to wait until year’s end, when he received his subjects’ taxes and tribute from his fief. Items like copper and iron were also called *jin*, not necessarily gold."

Zhuang Zhou flushed with anger and changed countenance, saying: "On my way here yesterday, I heard a voice calling from the middle of the road. I looked and saw a crucian carp in the rut of a carriage wheel. I asked it: 'Crucian carp! What are you doing here?' It replied: 'I am a wave-minister of the Eastern Sea. Might you have a dou or sheng of water to keep me alive?' I said: 'Very well. I am about to travel south to visit the kings of Wu and Yue. I will divert the waters of the Western River to welcome you. Will that do?'"—Master Cheng comments: "The Western River refers to the Shu River." The crucian carp flushed with anger and changed countenance, saying: "I have lost my usual element and have nowhere to dwell. A mere dou or sheng of water would suffice to keep me alive, yet you speak of this! You might as well seek me now in the dried-fish market!"

Lord Ren—Li comments: "Ren was the name of a state."—prepared a great hook and thick black line—Sima comments: "A thick black fishing line."—using fifty castrated oxen as bait. *Shiwen* notes: "犗 (*jiè*) means 'castrated ox.'" He crouched on Mount Kuaiji, cast his line into the Eastern Sea, and fished day after day for a year without catching anything. Then, a great fish took the bait, dragging the massive hook down in a plunging dive—*Shiwen* notes: "錎 (*xiàn*), according to the *Zilin*, means 'to sink' like the character 陷." It surged and thrashed, raising white waves like mountains. The sea churned violently, its roar rivaling that of ghosts and spirits, striking terror for a thousand *li*. Guo Qingfan comments: "惮 (*dàn*) denotes overwhelming might. Jia Yi’s *Xian Xian* chapter reads: 'Your Majesty’s awe-inspiring majesty'—the same sense as here."

Here the character *hè* (赫) also denotes fury—both instances refer to the fish. When Master Ren obtained this fish (若 here meaning "this"), he butchered and dried it. From the Zhe River eastward and Cangwu northward—*Zhì* (制) is interchangeable with *Zhè* (淛), the Zhejiang River. The ancient graphs "折" and "制" were used interchangeably. Sima comments: "This refers to present-day Qiantang in Kuaiji." Cangwu is a mountain name in Lingnan. None failed to be sated by this fish.

Thereafter, those of mediocre talent and pedantic persuasion were all astonished and spread the news. *Shiwen* notes: "Li [Yi] says: ‘*Quán* (辁) means ‘one of limited measure.’’ Some versions write *líng* (軨), meaning ‘petty.’ Others write *qīng* (轻), meaning ‘light.’" Now, those who brandish a pole with a thin line, chase after irrigation ditches, and guard minnows and gudgeons—Li [Yi] says: "Both are small fish"—how could they ever catch a great fish? Those who embellish trivial theories to seek high office are equally far from true attainment. Cheng [Xuanying] says: "*Gān* (干) means ‘to seek.’ *Xiàn* (县) means ‘lofty.’ *Lìng* (令) refers to *lìngwèn* (令问), ‘renown.’" Xuan [Ying] says: "*Xiànlìng* (县令) is like a reward standard."

Thus, those who have never heard of the customs of the Ren clan are equally far from being able to navigate the world. The Confucians rob graves in the name of the *Odes* and *Rites*. They seek "poetry and propriety" while plundering ancient tombs. A great Confucian proclaimed in a formal address—*Shiwen* notes: "The superior transmits words to the inferior in a *lú* (胪) address"—"The east is brightening—Master comments: "This refers to sunrise"—how goes the affair?" A lesser Confucian replied: "He had not yet removed his undergarments, and there was a pearl in his mouth. The *Odes* surely say: ‘The green-green wheat grows on the hillside mound. If one did not give in life, why hold a pearl in death?’" Sima comments: "This is a lost *ode* satirizing the dead." They grasped his temples—Cheng [Xuanying] says: "*Jiē* (接) means ‘to seize’"—and his whiskers—*Shiwen* notes: "*Zìlín* says: ‘一指按也’ (*yī zhǐ àn yě*), ‘to press with a finger.’ *Huì* (顪), *xǔhuì* (许秽) *fan*, Master says: ‘The hair beneath the chin.’"—The Confucians used a metal awl to pry open his jaw, carefully separating his cheeks, so as not to damage the pearl in his mouth! Cheng [Xuanying] says: "Tian Heng used benevolence and righteousness to usurp Qi; the Confucians recited the *Odes* and *Rites* to rob graves. From this, we see that the sagely way cannot be relied upon." Su Yu says: "Without the *Odes* and *Rites*, how would such villainy arise? This is one of Zhuangzi’s one-sided arguments, much like saying that Yao and Shun taught the people with benevolence and righteousness, yet it led to people devouring one another, and Tian Heng further used it to steal Qi."

When Lao Laizi’s disciple went out to gather firewood, he encountered Confucius and returned to report: "There is someone over there with a long upper body and a short lower body." Guo [Xiang] says: "Long above and short below."

The man had a hunched back with his ears set far back. Cheng [Xuanying] says: "His shoulders and back were stooped." Sima [Biao] says: "His ears were pushed backward." His gaze seemed to survey the Four Seas. Cheng says: "His gaze was lofty and distant, as if he were governing the world." No one knew whose son he was.

Lao Laizi said: "That is Qiu! Summon him here." Confucius arrived. Lao Laizi said: "Qiu! Shed your bodily self-importance and your display of wisdom. Guo [Xiang] says: 'Bodily self-importance refers to affected conduct; display of wisdom refers to the appearance of intelligence.' Only then can you be a true gentleman."

Confucius bowed and withdrew, his expression altered as he asked: "May I advance in my work?" Lao Laizi said: "You cannot bear the harm of this age yet recklessly court calamity for ten thousand generations. *Shiwen* says: '*Ao* is also written as *ao*, both meaning 'reckless.' This refers to Confucius being unable to endure the harm of this age yet arrogantly bequeathing calamity to ten thousand generations. Are you truly so impoverished in spirit? Or is it that your heart has long lacked any reserve, making you a destitute man? Or do your strategies simply not extend this far? Guo Qingfan says: '*Wu qi* should be read as *wu yi* ('or perhaps'). *Wu qi* is a rhetorical turn, as in the *Records of the Grand Historian*, *Biographies of Fan Sui and Cai Ze*: 'Or perhaps they say I am too base to be of use?' In *Master Lü’s Spring and Autumn Annals*, *Love of Kindred*: 'Or perhaps they would not take Song even if it were unjust to attack it?' And in the *Strategies of the Warring States*, *Han Strategies*: 'Or perhaps you have abandoned your methods and neglected your petitions?' In all cases, *wu qi* serves as a rhetorical turn, equivalent to modern 'or perhaps.' Is it that your wisdom and strategies do not extend this far?

To take delight in arrogance is a lifelong disgrace, a pursuit only the mediocre might advance in. Guo [Xiang] says: "The mediocre are ordinary people." Su Yu says: "The term *mediocre* also appears in the *Xu Wugui* chapter." Those who make bestowing benevolence their business seek the joy of the multitude and cultivate their own haughtiness—this is what it means to take delight in arrogance, a lifelong disgrace, something only the conduct of ordinary people might attain. They draw one another by name and bind one another in private favor. Yu [Yue] says: "*Yin* should be glossed as 'private.' The *Master Lü’s Spring and Autumn Annals*, *Circular Way* chapter, Gao You’s commentary says: '*Yin* means private.' In the *Wen Xuan*, *Rhapsody on the Red and White Horse*, Li Shan cites the *Guoyu* commentary: '*Yin* means private.' *Binding in private favor* means forming ties through private affection."

Rather than praising Yao and condemning Jie, it would be better to forget both and close off the very grounds for praise. Forgetting both good and evil, sealing off what might be praised, would also halt what might be condemned. This passage also appears in the *On the Equality of Things* chapter, where the last four characters read 'abandon their Way.'

To turn back is to do nothing that is not harmful; to move is to do nothing that is not perverse. Cheng says: "To turn against the nature of things is to do nothing that is not harmful; to disturb the mind is to do nothing that is not perverse."

The sage hesitates before acting, yet each endeavor succeeds. Cheng says: "Hesitation means proceeding unhurriedly. The sage, without preconceived intent, responds to circumstances and acts. In initiating affairs, he is always at ease, never going against the nature of things, so his achievements are always realized." Su Yu says: "*Each* is synonymous with *swiftly*. This means that in acting, he is not rushed, yet success comes quickly."

Why then do you carry this burden of the traces of benevolence and righteousness, only to end in self-importance?"

In the dead of night, Duke Yuan of Song dreamed of a man with disheveled hair peering through the curved eaves of the palace gate. Li’s commentary states: "This was Duke Yuan himself." (Note: Duke Yuan of Song was named Zuo, son of Duke Ping.) The man in the dream said: "I come from the Abyssal Pool of Zailu, a place named for the tortoises that dwell there. I was sent by the Clear River as an emissary to the River Lord, but the fisherman Yu Qiu caught me." Yu’s commentary notes: "In the *Records of Divination by Tortoiseshell*, this name appears as Yu Jū."

Duke Yuan awoke and had the dream interpreted. The diviner said: "This is a spirit tortoise." The Duke asked: "Is there a fisherman named Yu Qiu?" His attendants replied: "There is." The Duke commanded: "Summon Yu Qiu to court." The next day, Yu Qiu appeared. The Duke asked: "What did you catch in your fishing?" Yu Qiu answered: "In my net, I caught a white tortoise, five feet in circumference." The Duke said: "Present this tortoise to me."

When the tortoise arrived, the Duke wavered—first wanting to kill it, then to spare it. His mind unsettled, he consulted the oracle, which declared: "Slaying the tortoise for divination will bring good fortune." Thus, the tortoise was carved open, and in seventy-two drillings, not a single prediction failed. Every divination was accurate.

Confucius remarked: "The spirit tortoise could appear in a dream to Duke Yuan yet could not evade Yu Qiu’s net. It possessed the knowledge to yield seventy-two flawless divinations yet could not escape the calamity of having its entrails carved out. Thus, even knowledge has its limits, and the divine has its shortcomings. Though one may possess supreme wisdom, ten thousand schemers can outmaneuver it." Su Yu comments: "The wisdom of a single being cannot rival the schemes of ten thousand. In the *Mountain Trees* chapter, it is said, 'The worthy are plotted against, the unworthy are deceived'—here, 'plotted against' carries the same meaning as 'schemed against.' Fish fear not the net but the pelican. Those who rely on petty cunning are like fish unaware of the net’s danger."

Discard petty knowledge, and great wisdom becomes clear. Guo’s commentary states: "Petty knowledge is self-serving; great wisdom responds to things as they are." Abandon the pursuit of goodness, and one attains true goodness. Cheng’s commentary explains: "Cast aside the narrow mind of self-importance, and align with the great goodness of nature."

An infant, born without a learned teacher, can still speak—because it dwells among those who can speak. The *Exegesis* notes: "Shi may also be written as shuo." (Note: "Shi" and "shuo" were interchangeable in ancient script.) Xuan comments: "The unlearned possess natural ability."

Zhuangzi said to Huizi, "You say my words are useless." Huizi replied, "Only when one understands uselessness can we speak of use. The earth is neither narrow nor small, yet what people use is only enough to stand upon. If, however, one were to dig away all the earth beyond where the foot rests, down to the Yellow Springs, would it still be of use?" The *Exegesis* notes: "厕 is pronounced *cè*. 垫 means 'to descend' or 'to dig.' 致 means 'to reach'; in some texts, it is written as 至." (Note: This suggests that while the earth is vast and largely unused, if one were to excavate all but the space where the foot stands down to the Yellow Springs, would what remains still be of use?) Huizi said, "It would be useless." Zhuangzi replied, "Thus, the usefulness of the useless is also clear."

Zhuangzi said, "If a person has the ability to wander freely, how could they not wander? If one can find contentment in themselves, what could prevent them from being content? If a person cannot wander freely, how could they wander at all? If one cannot find contentment in themselves, how could they ever be content? Those with the will to flee, the resolve to sever ties, to drift and hide away, to cut themselves off from the world—alas! Are these not the burdens of those lacking perfect wisdom and profound virtue? The path of true wisdom and great virtue is not like this. To stumble and not turn back, to rush forward like wildfire without a glance behind—this is the way of those who cling desperately to worldly engagement. Su Yu notes: 'The phrase *huǒ chí* (fire-driving) also appears in the *Tiandi* chapter.' Though they may serve as ruler and minister to one another, it is merely the way of the times, a temporary alignment. When the age changes, they will no longer hold each other in contempt. Thus, it is said: the perfected person does not linger in their actions. The perfected one moves through this without attachment. Those who exalt the past and belittle the present belong to the current of scholars. Yet if we observe the present age through the lens of the ancient Xiwei clan, who could remain unmoved? If we view today’s world through the pure customs of antiquity, who could remain untroubled within? *Bō* means 'to move.' Only the perfected person can wander through the world without bias, moving with the times without becoming ensnared by them, unlike those who flee or sever ties. They follow others without losing themselves, unlike those who stumble blindly or rush forward like wildfire. They do not learn from the teachings that exalt the past and belittle the present, yet they align with their intent without opposition.

The eyes, when unobstructed, see clearly; the ears, when unobstructed, hear keenly; the nose, when unobstructed, perceives scents. Cheng comments: "颤 refers to pungent and foul odors." The mouth, when unobstructed, tastes sweetness; the heart, when unobstructed, attains wisdom; wisdom, when unobstructed, becomes virtue. (Note: The pronunciation of 知 here is *zhì*.) 彻 means 'to penetrate.' The Dao does not desire obstruction; obstruction leads to choking, and if choking persists without relief, it results in trampling. The Dao is the path all people follow; it does not desire obstruction. Obstruction leads to choking, and if choking is not resolved, it causes reckless behavior and mutual trampling. Guo comments: "跈 means 'to trample.'" Trampling gives rise to manifold harms. Guo comments: "生 means 'to arise.'" Living beings with consciousness rely on breath. Xuan comments: "Breath circulates the qi throughout the body." If it is not balanced, it is not the fault of Heaven. *Yīn* means 'proper.' If it is not proper, it is not Heaven’s error, for Heaven’s endowment of nature is always harmonious. Heaven bores through things day and night without ceasing. Cheng comments: "降 means 'to stop.' The principle of nature bores through all things, from day to night, without pause." Yet people, in contrast, block their own apertures. Cheng comments: "窦 means 'orifices.' The common people, contrary to Heaven’s principle, obstruct their root openings and become mired in stagnation."

The womb has layered chambers. The *Exegesis* notes: "胞 refers to the fetus in the abdomen." Guo comments: "阆 means 'spacious.'" Cheng comments: "The human abdomen is hollow, allowing it to contain the stomach; the stomach is hollow, allowing qi and fluids to circulate." The heart has the freedom of Heaven’s wandering. Xuan comments: "The heart must have space to align with Heaven’s mechanism." If a room has no emptiness, then mistress and daughter-in-law will quarrel. Sima comments: "勃溪 means 'to turn against one another.'" Xuan comments: "勃溪 means 'to be cramped and in conflict.' If a room has no space, then the elder and younger will be cramped and trample one another." If the heart has no freedom of Heaven’s wandering, then the six senses will vie with one another. Xuan comments: "The six senses are the six faculties that carve up one’s nature. Without space for leisure, the six senses take over and usurp one’s nature." The great forests and mountains are beneficial to people, yet even they cannot match the spirit’s capacity. Xuan comments: "If the heart has the freedom of Heaven’s wandering, then within the square inch of the mind, one may roam boundlessly—why seek vast and open places for contentment? Yet people delight in the expansiveness of great forests and mountains, for their minds are cramped and their spirits constrained."

Virtue overflows into fame, and fame overflows into notoriety. Guo Songtao comments: "Virtue overflows because of fame; fame overflows because it is displayed and made known. The *Xunzi*’s *Fuguo* chapter states: 'Reputation is sufficient to expose and roast one.'" Scheming arises from urgency. Guo comments: "誸 means 'urgent.' Only in urgency does one deliberate over plans." Wisdom emerges from conflict. Xuan comments: "Conflict spurs the display of intelligence." Isolation arises from steadfastness. *Chái* means 'to stand alone.' Only with steadfastness can one stand alone without fear. The *Dasheng* chapter states: "Standing alone in the center." Official affairs are settled by the consensus of the many. The affairs of office must be agreed upon by all before they can be decisively carried out. When spring rains come in season, grasses and trees flourish vigorously. (Note: "日" is likely a scribal error for "曰.")

The practice of plowing and weeding thus begins in earnest. Cheng comments: "The *yao* is a kind of plowshare; the *nou* is a hoe." More than half of the grasses and trees that are uprooted and replanted take root anew, yet none know why this is so. *Shiwen* notes: "植 means 'to stand.' Sima comments: 'When uprooted and replanted, they revive—this is called *daozhi*.'" Lu comments: "到 is the ancient form of 倒 (*inverted*)." Cheng comments: "When grasses are uprooted and regrow, it is the season that makes it so. Thus, in establishing laws and teachings, one must follow the proper time."

Stillness can remedy illness. Xuan comments: "In stillness, spirit and qi return, thus it can remedy illness." Pressing and massaging the corners of the eyes can rejuvenate the aged. *Shiwen* notes: "● is also written as 搣." Guo Songtao comments: "The *Guangyun* states: '搣 means to press or rub.' This refers to massaging the corners of the eyes with both hands." Xuan comments: "This is likely a technique of nurturing life, which can bathe and restore an aged countenance." Tranquility can halt haste. Xuan comments: "When one is tranquil and settled, the mind is at ease, and this can halt urgency."

Yet even so, these are but the tasks of the toiling. Xuan comments: "This merely teaches the laboring how to find respite." They are not the concerns that the carefree have ever encountered or inquired after. Xuan comments: "Those who are truly carefree do not engage in such matters." (Note: The character "非" is likely an interpolation.)

The means by which the sage startles the world are matters the spirit-like person has never encountered or inquired after. The sage—such as the Yellow Emperor, Yao, or Shun—and the spirit-like person—such as Guangcheng or Daiwei—operate on different planes. The means by which the worthy startles the age are matters the sage has never encountered or inquired after. Figures like Wuguang and Shentu Di were likely worthy persons. The means by which the noble person startles the state are matters the worthy has never encountered or inquired after. The noble person who startles the state may be akin to Tian Heng and his ilk. The means by which the petty person conforms to the times are matters the noble person has never encountered or inquired after.

At Yanmen, there was one whose parent had died. *Shiwen* notes: "Yanmen was the name of a city gate in Song." He mourned so excessively that he wasted away. For this, he was enfeoffed as an official and master, and the Lord of Song honored his filial conduct. Half of his fellow villagers followed suit, mourning to death. Guo comments: "To feign mourning for reward is far from true virtue—this is the fault of exalting the worthy." *Dang* here refers to one’s village or community.

When Yao offered the world to Xu You, Xu You fled from it. When Tang offered the world to Wuguang, Wuguang grew angry. When Ji Ta heard of this, he led his disciples to crouch by the Kuǎn River. The feudal lords mourned him for three years, and Shentu Di, moved by this, drowned himself in the river. *Shiwen* notes: "踆 is explained in the *Zilin* as the ancient form of 蹲 (*to crouch*). Sima comments: 'Kuǎn is the name of a river.'" Cheng comments: "Fearing he would be implicated, Ji Ta crouched by the water with his disciples. The feudal lords, hearing of this and admiring his integrity, came to offer condolences, fearing he would drown himself. Shentu Di, upon learning of this, emulated his lofty reputation and threw himself into the river."

The fish trap exists for the sake of catching fish; once the fish is caught, the trap is forgotten.

The *Shiwen* notes: "荃 (*quan*), Cui reads as *sun*, referring to a fragrant herb used as bait for fish. Some say it means piling firewood in water to attract fish to feed. Another says it is a fish trap." Lu comments: "As some have said, this refers to *qian* (a submerged weir). See the *Zhou Song* in the *Book of Poetry*." Note: Cheng’s edition writes it as 筌 (*quan*). 在 (*zai*) means to capture alive. 蹄 (*ti*) is what traps the hare; once the hare is caught, the trap is forgotten. The *Shiwen* notes: "蹄 is a snare for hares, binding their legs—hence the name." Words exist for the sake of meaning; once the meaning is grasped, the words are forgotten. How can I find a man who has forgotten words to speak with him?

---

**Miscellaneous Chapters: "Parables" (Chapter Twenty-Seven)**
"Parables" make up nineteen out of ten. Xuan comments: "Words lodged in one place, nine out of ten." Note: The meaning lies here, but the words are lodged elsewhere. "Weighted words" make up seven out of ten. Xuan comments: "Cited words of weight, seven out of ten." *Zhiyan* emerge daily. The *Shiwen* notes: "卮 (*zhi*), also written as 巵, pronounced *zhi*. The *Zilüe* says: 'A round wine vessel.' Wang comments: 'A *zhi* vessel tips when full and rights itself when empty, adapting to things rather than clinging to one form. Applied to words, it follows others and changes, having no fixed position of its own.'" Guo comments: "日出 (*ri chu*) means daily renewal." Harmonized with the Heavenly Boundary. Cheng comments: "和 (*he*) means to accord. 天倪 (*tian ni*) is the natural division." Note: It means responding only in accordance with nature. "Parables" (nineteen out of ten) rely on external voices. Guo comments: "Words from oneself are often rejected by the world, so one borrows external voices—like Jianwu and Lianshu." A father does not act as his son’s matchmaker. When a father praises his son, it is not as convincing as when someone else does. Cheng comments: "媒 (*mei*) means to bring together. When a father speaks of his son, few believe him; when another praises him, many do." This is not my fault but the fault of others. It is not that I deliberately act in a disjointed way; rather, others wrongly raise doubts. If something aligns with oneself, one responds; if it does not, one rejects it. What aligns with oneself is deemed right; what differs is deemed wrong. Human nature fixates on agreement and disagreement to determine right and wrong, hence the need for parables. "Weighted words" (seven out of ten): Yao comments: "In Zhuangzi’s writings, nine out of ten are words attributed to others. Among these parables, those attributed to figures like Shennong, the Yellow Emperor, Yao, Shun, Confucius, and Yan Hui—words weighty enough to be revered by the world—make up seven out of ten." This is to silence words. 已 (*yi*) means to stop—to halt the world’s confused and disorderly speech. These are the words of elders. Such are the words of the venerable, and so they are cited. The *Shigu* says: "耆艾 (*qi ai*) means elders." One may be older in years, but without the warp and weft of beginnings and ends to mark one as venerable, this is not true precedence. In handling affairs, the warp and weft matter; in establishing words, beginnings and ends matter. What is valued in elders is not just age but the Dao. If one is merely older in years but lacks the warp and weft of beginnings and ends, how can one be considered truly prior? Su Yu comments: "期 (*qi*) means a limit. To say one has nothing to precede others except age is to use age as a limit. As in the *Yang* chapter: 'Counting the number of things does not stop at ten thousand, yet we set a limit by calling them the ten thousand things,' the word 期 here shares the same meaning." A person who has nothing to precede others lacks the human Dao. Xuan comments: "Cannot fulfill the human Way." A person who lacks the human Dao is called a "worn-out person." Guo comments: "Merely a long-worn person." Xuan comments: "Like a withered old thing." *Zhiyan* emerge daily, harmonized with the Heavenly Boundary, flowing and extending, thus exhausting the years. Following the principles of things and letting them flow and extend, words emerge endlessly, merely to pass the years. As in the *Qiwulun*: "Harmonized with the Heavenly Boundary, flowing and extending, thus exhausting the years." Without words, things are equal. Equality with words is not equality; words with equality are not equality. Thus, it is said: "No words." Su Yu comments: "When there are no words, the Dao is present, and all things are equal. With words, there is correctness and error; equality with words or words with equality can never truly be equal. Hence, it is said: 'Better to have no words.'" Words without words: Guo comments: "Speaking those words, yet for me, they remain no words." Speaking all one’s life, one has never spoken; not speaking all one’s life, one has never not spoken. There is a source from which something may be permissible, and a source from which it may not. There is a source from which something may be so, and a source from which it may not. Guo comments: "自 (*zi*) means *from*. From the bias of self and other, there arises permissibility and impermissibility, so-ness and not-so-ness." How is it so? So in being so. How is it not so? Not so in not being so. How is it permissible? Permissible in being permissible. How is it impermissible? Impermissible in being impermissible. Things inherently have what is so; things inherently have what is permissible. There is nothing that is not so, nothing that is not permissible. The above also appears in the *Qiwulun* chapter. Without *zhiyan* emerging daily, harmonized with the Heavenly Boundary, who could endure for long? Without these wordless words, who could transmit them for long? All things are seeds, succeeding one another with different forms. Xuan comments: "All have their kinds, each with its form succeeding endlessly." Beginning and end are like a ring—none can grasp its principle. Guo comments: "伦 (*lun*) means principle." Note: Like a ring without end, none can grasp its principle. This is called the Heavenly Equality. Heavenly Equality is the Heavenly Boundary. Cheng comments: "均 (*jun*) means equal. This is called the natural equalizing Dao."

"Even the Dao of equalizing equality is also called the natural division."

Note: The *Qiwulun* also states: "Therefore, the sage harmonizes with right and wrong and rests in the Heavenly Equality—this is called the twofold course." Zhuangzi said to Huizi: "Confucius lived sixty years, and in those sixty years, he transformed sixty times. What he initially affirmed, he later negated. Who knows whether what he now calls right is not what he called wrong fifty-nine years ago?" This aligns with the *Zeyang* chapter’s account of Qu Boyu.

Huizi said: "Confucius toils his will and serves knowledge."
Xuan comments: "This suggests Confucius labored his mind and engaged in excessive knowledge, yet had not attained transformation."

Zhuangzi said: "Confucius has already renounced this, though he has not yet spoken of it."
Xuan comments: "This means Confucius had already abandoned the traces of toil and advanced in the Dao, though he had not yet expressed it in words."

Confucius said:
Xuan comments: "Citing Confucius’ refined speech."

“One receives one’s endowment from the Great Root and restores the numinous to live.”
The Great Root is Heaven. Man receives his endowment from Heaven and restores his nature’s numinosity to live.
When he sings in accord with the pitch-pipes, his voice becomes the pitch-pipes. When he speaks in accord with the law, his words become the law for all under Heaven. Though profit and duty lie before him, his likes and dislikes, right and wrong, merely submit to men’s mouths. Yet he enables men to submit in their hearts and dare not stand in opposition, thus establishing the world’s stability.
*Shiwen* notes: “蘁 is pronounced *wu*, meaning ‘to oppose.’”
Commentary: This means it merely submits to men’s mouths. Yet by making men submit in their hearts and dare not oppose, such a one suffices to establish the world’s settled principle.
Thus spoke the Master.
“Enough! Enough! I shall never attain his level!”
Cheng comments: “This is Zhuangzi’s praise of Confucius.”
Yao comments: “The toil of will and service of knowledge—what Confucius taught his disciples—were not what made Confucius Confucius. Thus he ‘renounced this, though he had not yet spoken of it.’ What made Confucius Confucius was the ‘Confucius said’ above. Why? Because the Great Root abides therein. Receiving one’s endowment from the Great Root and restoring goodness to return to one’s life—this is what made Confucius Confucius, nothing more than returning to the Great. As for singing in accord with the pitch-pipes, speaking in accord with the law, and clarifying right and wrong, these are minor virtues. How could they define Confucius?”
This interpretation is also worth considering.

Zengzi took office twice, and his heart transformed twice.
Xuan comments: “Transform means to change.”
He said: “When I served while my parents were alive, three *fu* brought me joy.”
Cheng comments: “Six *dou* and four *sheng* make one *fu*.”
“Later, when I served, three thousand *zhong* did not reach them.”
Cheng comments: “Six *hu* and four *dou* make one *zhong*. *Ji* means ‘to reach.’”
Commentary: He could not reach his parents.
“I grieved in my heart.”
A disciple asked Confucius: “Can one say that Shen here is free from the fault of being bound?”
Guo comments: “Bound means to be tied.”
Xuan comments: “Serving for the sake of one’s parents, his heart was free from the fault of being tied to emolument.”
Confucius said: “He was already bound.”
Xuan comments: “He was already tied to emolument and support.”
“Can one who is free from being bound feel sorrow?”
Cheng comments: “The filial son serving his parents focuses on suitability, regardless of the emolument’s thickness or thinness. He exhausts himself in demeanor and support, hence there are hired laborers who are called filial sons, while three offices still count as unfilial. Since his heart holds sorrow and joy, how can he be free from the fault of being tied to emolument? Only one free from ties should be free from sorrow and joy.”
“To him, three *fu* and three thousand *zhong* were like watching midges and gnats passing before him.”
“Him” refers to one free from ties.
Yu comments: “The character ‘sparrow’ is superfluous. *Shiwen* states: ‘The Yuanjia edition reads “like storks and midges,” omitting “gnats.”’ Thus, the edition Lu saw did not have the superfluous ‘sparrow,’ for it only mentions the Yuanjia edition lacking ‘gnats,’ not lacking ‘sparrow.’ Yet storks and midges—one bird, one insect—make an incongruous comparison. Wang comments: ‘Storks and midges take large and small as their distinction, to illustrate the disparity between three *fu* and three thousand *zhong*.’ But the perfected man’s view of things is but a sip—how could he be concerned with the disparity between three *fu* and three thousand *zhong*, or distinguish between storks and midges? *Shiwen* further notes: ‘Storks were originally written as “watching.”’ I suspect the ancient text read: ‘To him, three *fu* and three thousand *zhong* were like watching midges and gnats pass before him.’ The *Huainanzi*’s *Chuzhen* chapter states: ‘Praise and blame to oneself are like a midge or gnat’s passing.’ The meaning is the same. When ‘watching’ was mistaken for ‘stork,’ the three characters ‘stork, midge, gnat’ became incongruous, leading the Yuanjia edition to delete one ‘gnat’ to balance ‘stork’ and ‘midge,’ and the present edition to add one ‘sparrow’ to balance ‘stork, sparrow’ with ‘midge, gnat.’ Neither preserves Zhuangzi’s original text.”

Yan Chengzi You said to Dongguo Ziqi, “As Cheng explains: ‘He lived east of the outer wall, hence called Dongguo, the same as Nan Guo Ziqi in the *Equalizing Things* chapter.’ Since I heard your words, after one year I became rustic—Cheng explains: ‘Rustic means unadorned. After hearing the Dao for a year, with the heart’s learning not yet mature, one can only gradually shed superficiality and return to plainness.’ After two years I followed—Cheng explains: ‘Accorded with the common.’ After three years I penetrated—Cheng explains: ‘No longer stagnated by circumstances.’ After four years I became one with things—Cheng explains: ‘Merged with things.’ After five years they came to me—Cheng explains: ‘Drew the multitude to return.’ After six years spirits entered—Cheng explains: ‘Spirit met the pattern of things.’ After seven years Heaven was complete—Cheng explains: ‘Accorded with natural completion.’ After eight years I knew not death, knew not life—Cheng explains: ‘Unaware of the distinction between life and death, gathering and dispersal.’ After nine years I attained the great mystery—Cheng explains: ‘Mystery means subtlety. Knowing and illumination were vast and broad, hence called great.’

Life with deliberate action is death. Guo explains: ‘To live with deliberate action is to lose one’s life.’ He admonished the duke, saying: ‘As in the following two lines.’ Because death has its source—Guo explains: ‘Source means cause. Death comes from deliberate action; deliberate action comes from prizing one’s life.’ But life as yang has no source—Xuan explains: ‘Death is yin, life is yang.’ Guo explains: ‘Life as yang arises from leaving no trace and acting without deliberate effort; it has no cause.’ Could you truly do so? *Er*, you. Could you truly act without deliberate effort? Where would you find what suits you? Where would you find what does not suit you?—Cheng explains: ‘Everywhere is suitable.’ Heaven has its celestial reckoning, qi and number have their fixed order. Earth has its human claims, each claiming its place. Where should I seek it?—Cheng explains: ‘Where should I seek it beyond what is already here?’ If we know not its end, how can we say there is no fate?—Cheng explains: ‘As time comes and fortune goes, how could it not be fate! This means there is fate.’ If we know not its beginning, how can we say there is fate?—Cheng explains: ‘Life and death, coming and going, are like spring, autumn, winter, and summer—could this be fate? This means there is no fate.’ If there is a response, how can we say there are no spirits?—Guo explains: ‘Pattern must have a response, as if spirits brought it about.’ If there is no response, how can we say there are spirits?’ The principle of response sometimes fails to manifest.

The multitude of *wangliang* asked the shadow, ‘The faint yin beyond the shadow is manifold, hence called the multitude of *wangliang*. “Earlier you were bent, now you are upright—*ruo*, you. Earlier your hair was bound, now it is loose—bound means tied up. Earlier you sat, now you stand. Earlier you walked, now you stop. Why?” The shadow said: “So trifling!—*Sousou* is also written as *sou*. Cheng explains: ‘*Sousou* describes effortless movement.’ Why do you hastily ask? I have it but know not why. Though I clearly have it, I know not how it comes to be. I am like the cicada’s shell, the snake’s slough—seemingly so but not so. Xuan explains: ‘Shell and slough still have a fixed form, hence seemingly so but not so.’”

Fire and sun, I gather—*Tun* means to assemble. Xuan explains: ‘With fire and sun, I assemble and become visible.’ Yin and night, I alternate—Sima explains: ‘Alternate means to allow rest.’ That—referring to form—is what I depend on? How much more so for those who depend on what they depend on!—Referring to form depending on Heaven’s mechanism to move. The *Equalizing Things* chapter says: ‘What I depend on also depends on something else to be so?’ When it comes, I come with it; when it goes, I go with it; when it is vigorously yang, I am vigorously yang with it—Xuan explains: ‘Vigorously yang means vigorous movement.’ As for the vigorously yang, what question could there be?’ *You* here means the *you* in ‘I have it’ above. How could the vigorously moving ask about me!’

Yang Ziju went south to Pei—*Liezi*’s *Yellow Emperor* chapter has Yang Zhu. Lao Dan traveled west to Qin and was invited at the outskirts—*Yao* means to invite. Xuan explains: ‘Ziju invited Laozi at the outskirts of Pei.’ Arriving at Liang, he met Laozi. Xuan explains: ‘Liang is a place name in the outskirts of Pei.’ Midway, Laozi looked up to heaven and sighed: “At first I thought you could be taught, but now you cannot.” Yang Ziju did not reply. Upon reaching the lodging, he brought water for washing, rinsing, towel, and comb—*Huangdi* chapter has *guan* for *guan*. He removed his shoes outside the door and advanced on his knees, saying: “Earlier your disciple wished to ask you, Master, but you were occupied on the road, so I dared not. Now you are at leisure; may I ask what my fault is?” Laozi said: “Your haughty and overbearing demeanor—Guo explains: ‘Arrogant in appearance. People will fear and distance themselves from you.’ Who would dwell with you? Who would live with you? The greatest whiteness seems sullied, the fullest virtue seems insufficient.” Sullied means stained. This is a passage from the *Daodejing*. Yang Ziju’s expression changed abruptly, and he said: “I respectfully receive your instruction.”

When he had gone before, the innkeeper’s family welcomed him—Zhang Zhan’s note: ‘The innkeeper’s family.’ The master of the house held the mat, his wife held the towel and comb, and the innkeeper left his seat—Cheng explains: ‘Those who had been seated left their seats and departed.’”

The fire-tender avoided the stove—Cheng explains: “One who kindles the fire dared not occupy the stove.” On his return, the innkeeper’s family now vied with him for seats—Guo explains: “Because he had shed his arrogance.”

*Zhuangzi Jijie*, Scroll Eight
Miscellaneous Chapters: "Yielding the Throne," Chapter Twenty-Eight

The four chapters of "Yielding the Throne" below are widely regarded by ancient and modern scholars as spurious.

Yao offered the empire to Xu You, but Xu You declined. He also offered it to Zizhou Zhipu—Li explains: “Zhipu was his courtesy name; he was also called Zhibo.” Zizhou Zhipu said: “That you would make me Son of Heaven is acceptable. Nevertheless, I happen to suffer from a deep-seated affliction—Wang explains: “His illness is profound and entrenched.” I am just now treating it and have no leisure to govern the empire.” The empire is the weightiest of burdens, yet he would not let it harm his life—how much less would he allow other things to do so! Only one who takes no account of the empire can be entrusted with it.

Shun offered the empire to Zizhou Zhibo, and Zizhou Zhibo said: “I happen to suffer from a deep-seated affliction and am just now treating it; I have no leisure to govern the empire.” Thus, the empire is a great vessel, yet one does not exchange life for it—this is how those who embody the Dao differ from the common world.

Shun offered the empire to Shan Juan, and Shan Juan said: “I stand in the midst of the cosmos: in winter I wear furs, in summer I wear coarse or fine linen; in spring I plow and sow, and my body is sufficient for labor; in autumn I reap and gather, and my person is sufficient for rest.”

With the sun’s rise, I labor; with the sun’s setting, I rest—roaming free between heaven and earth, my heart content. Why should I take the empire? Alas! You do not understand me at all!

Thus he declined. Then he departed and entered the deep mountains, and none knew his whereabouts.

Shun offered the empire to his friend, the farmer of Shihu. The farmer of Shihu said: “How earnest and diligent is this man! A true preserver of strength.” (The character *hu* is also written as *hou*.) This *hou* then spoke of himself, saying: “I toil earnestly and laboriously—such is the man who preserves his strength. I have no leisure to govern the empire.” He deemed Shun’s virtue insufficient, so he shouldered his wife and carried his child on his head, entering the sea and never returning.

King Tai of Danfu dwelt in Bin, and the Di tribes attacked him. He offered them furs and silks, but they refused; he offered them dogs and horses, but they refused; he offered them pearls and jade, but they refused—the Di sought only land. King Tai of Danfu said: “To dwell with a man’s elder brother and kill his younger brother, to dwell with a man’s father and kill his son—this I cannot bear. Go, all of you, and dwell here! What difference is there between serving me and serving the Di?” Moreover, I have heard that one must not harm what is nurtured by what is used for nurturing. (What is used for nurturing is land; what is nurtured is the people.) So he took up his staff and departed. The people followed him in droves, and he established a state at the foot of Mount Qi. King Tai of Danfu could indeed be called one who honored life, valuing life above all. Those who honor life, though noble and wealthy, do not harm their bodies with excess; though poor and lowly, they do not burden their forms with profit. Those with means do not injure themselves with indulgence, and those without profit do not weary their forms in pursuit of wealth. Yet the people of this age, who hold high office and exalted rank, all fear losing them and cling to them dearly. Seeing profit, they lightly risk their lives—how deluded they are!

The people of Yue assassinated their ruler for three generations. Prince Sou, fearing this fate, fled into the cinnabar caves. (Li: “Sou is the prince’s name.” *Huainanzi* writes it as *Yi*. The *Erya* says: “The southern region where the sun shines is called the cinnabar caves.” Cheng: “The cinnabar caves are caverns in the southern mountains.” Yu: “There is no record of three generations of regicide before Yi. The *Shiji*’s *Yue Shijia* *Suoyin* identifies Sou as the son of Yi, Wuzhuan. According to the *Bamboo Annals*, Yi was assassinated by his son, the people of Yue killed his son and enthroned Wuyu, who was also assassinated and succeeded by Wuzhuan. Thus, Wuzhuan’s three predecessors all met violent ends, so Prince Sou is undoubtedly another name for Wuzhuan. The *Huainanzi*’s account is likely a mishearing and should be corrected according to the *Suoyin*.”) As Yue had no ruler, they sought Prince Sou but could not find him, so they followed him to the cinnabar caves. Prince Sou refused to emerge, so the people of Yue smoked him out with mugwort and bore him away in the royal carriage. Prince Sou grasped the carriage strap to mount it, then looked up to heaven and cried: “Rulership! Rulership! Can you not spare me even this?” Prince Sou did not hate being a ruler—he hated the calamity that comes with rulership. Such a man could truly be called one who does not harm his life for the sake of the state. This is precisely what the people of Yue desired in a ruler.

Han and Wei contended over disputed territory. Zihua Zi visited Marquis Zhao of Han, who wore a troubled expression. (Sima: “Zihua Zi was a man of Wei. Zhao was the marquis of Han.” Yu: “The *Lüshi Chunqiu*’s *Guisheng* chapter quotes Zihua Zi: ‘Preserving life is supreme; impairing life is secondary; death is next; coerced life is worst.’ The *Wutu* chapter also quotes Zihua Zi: ‘Kings delight in what makes them kings; the ruined delight in what ruins them.’ Gao’s commentary on both calls Zihua Zi ‘an ancient Daoist.’ The *Zhidu* and *Shenwei* chapters’ commentaries concur. Han had a Marquis Zhao and a King Xi, but no Marquis Zhao-Xi.”) Zihua Zi said: “Suppose the world inscribed a decree before you, saying: ‘If you seize it with your left hand, your right hand will be maimed; if you seize it with your right hand, your left hand will be maimed. Yet whoever seizes it will gain the world.’ Would you seize it?” (Cheng: “The decree is a written record.” Sima: “Maimed means injured.” Another interpretation: “Seizing refers to grasping the decree; maimed means severing the right hand.”) Marquis Zhao said: “I would not seize it.” Zihua Zi said: “Excellent! From this we see that your two arms are weightier than the world, and your body is weightier than your two arms. Han’s value is far lighter than the world, and what you now contend over is far lighter than Han. Yet you grieve over harming your life and body for such trivial gains!” (He grieved over not obtaining them.) The marquis said: “Well said! Many have instructed me, but I have never heard such words before.” Zihua Zi could indeed be called one who understood the weight of things. (The character *Zhao* is missing before *Xi*.)

The lord of Lu heard that Yan He was a man who had attained the Dao, so he sent an envoy with gifts to precede him. Yan He dwelt in a humble lane, wearing a coarse hempen robe and feeding his own oxen. The lord of Lu’s envoy arrived, and Yan He personally received him. The envoy asked: “Is this Yan He’s home?” Yan He replied: “This is He’s home.”

The envoy presented the gifts, and Yan He said: “I fear the listener may have misunderstood, thereby bringing blame upon the envoy—it would be better to verify.” (Yu Yue notes: “The character *zhe* below *ting* is superfluous; it does not appear in the *Lüshi Chunqiu*’s ‘Valuing Life’ chapter.) The envoy returned and re-examined the matter, then came back to seek him, but Yan He had already departed. Thus, one like Yan He truly detested wealth and honor.

Therefore it is said: The essence of the Dao is for cultivating the self; its surplus threads may be used to govern the state; its dregs and chaff may be applied to ordering the world. (Sima Biao notes: “*Tu zha* refers to things like manure and straw.”) From this perspective, the achievements of emperors and kings are but the leftover affairs of sages—never the means to perfect the self or nurture life.

Yet the gentlemen of this vulgar age often cast aside their lives and abandon their well-being to pursue external things—is this not lamentable? Whenever sages act, they must examine their intended direction and their underlying purpose. (Wang Niansun notes: “‘Intended direction’ refers to the realm where virtue is applied; ‘underlying purpose’ refers to how they engage with things.”)

Now suppose there were a man here who used a Marquis Sui’s pearl to shoot at a sparrow a thousand *ren* high—surely the world would ridicule him. Why? Because what he employed was weighty, while what he sought was trivial.

For life—how could its value be merely that of a Marquis Sui’s pearl? (Yu Yue notes: “In the *Noble Life* chapter, the character *pearl* follows *Marquis*; it should be added here.”) Master Liezi was impoverished, his countenance bearing the hue of hunger. A guest spoke of this to Ziyang of Zheng, saying: “Lie Yukou is a man of the Dao—a scholar of worth. That he dwells in your state yet suffers want—might this not suggest you hold scholars in low regard?” (The *Exegesis* notes: “Ziyang was the chief minister of Zheng.”) Ziyang of Zheng promptly ordered an official to send him grain. (Cheng Xuanying comments: “The official in charge of the granary.”) When Master Liezi saw the messenger, he bowed twice and declined. After the messenger departed, Liezi entered his home, where his wife, gazing at him, struck her breast and said: “I have heard that the wives and children of those who follow the Dao all live in ease and joy. Yet here you are, starving. The lord, recognizing his oversight, sent you food—an act of kindness from one who heeded his minister’s counsel. That you refuse it—is this not a matter of fate?” Master Liezi smiled and replied: “The lord does not know me of his own accord. He sent me grain on another’s word, and should he condemn me, it would likewise be on another’s word. This is why I did not accept.” In the end, the people indeed rose in revolt and killed Ziyang. (Yu Yue notes: “The affair of Ziyang is recorded in the *Appropriate Might* chapter of the *Lüshi Chunqiu* and the *Fanlun Xun* of the *Huainanzi*. In the *Records of the Historian*, the *Hereditary House of Zheng* states: ‘In the twenty-fifth year of Duke Ru, the Duke of Zheng killed his chief minister Ziyang. In the twenty-seventh year, Ziyang’s faction jointly assassinated Duke Ru the Feeble,’ which differs from the accounts in other texts.”)

When King Zhao of Chu lost his state, the butcher Yue fled alongside him. After King Zhao regained his kingdom, he sought to reward those who had followed him, including the butcher Yue. The butcher Yue said: “When Your Majesty lost your kingdom, I lost my butcher’s trade. Now that Your Majesty has regained your kingdom, I have likewise regained my butcher’s trade. My rank and emolument have been restored—what reward is there to speak of?” The king said: “Insist!” And so he compelled him to accept the reward.

The butcher Yue said: “When Your Majesty lost your kingdom, it was not this subject’s fault, and so I dared not submit to punishment. Now that Your Majesty has regained your kingdom, it is not this subject’s merit, and so I dare not accept reward.” The king said: “Summon him!” The butcher Yue replied: “By the laws of Chu, only those who have rendered great merit through heavy reward may be granted an audience. Now, my wisdom was insufficient to preserve the kingdom, and my courage was insufficient to die resisting the enemy. When the Wu army entered Ying, I fled in fear of danger and avoided the foe—not that I deliberately followed Your Majesty. If Your Majesty now wishes to set aside the law and break the covenant to grant me an audience, this is not what I would wish to be heard of throughout the world.”

The king said to Marshal Ziqí: “Though the butcher Yue dwells in lowly and humble circumstances, his exposition of principle is most lofty. Ziqí, extend to him the position of the Three Standards.” *Exegesis*: “The Three Standards refer to the rank of the Three Dukes. The *Records of the Marshal* reads ‘Three Jade-Tablets,’ explaining: ‘This refers to the three ministers of the feudal lords, each of whom holds a tablet.’” Xuan [Yun] comments: “Carriages and robes each have their distinguishing standards, hence the term ‘Three Standards.’” Yu [Yue] comments: “‘Qí’ is a superfluous character.” *Note*: “Qí” may have been mistakenly written for “qí” (其).

The butcher Yue said: “The position of the Three Standards—I know it is nobler than my butcher’s stall. The emolument of ten thousand bushels—I know it is richer than the profits of my butcher’s trade. Yet how could I accept rank and salary and thereby cause my lord to bear the reputation of reckless bestowal? I dare not accept. I wish only to return to my butcher’s stall.” Thus he refused to accept.

Yuan Xian dwelled in Lu, in a wattle hut with walls no more than a fathom square, thatched with living grass. *Exegesis*: “Covering a house with grass is called thatching.” Its door of woven mugwort was incomplete, its hinge made of mulberry wood, and its window a broken urn. *Exegesis*: “The door’s pivot was made of bent mulberry branches; the window was fashioned from a shattered urn.” It had two rooms. *Exegesis*: “One for husband, one for wife.” He stuffed the gaps with coarse cloth. *Exegesis*: “He used a hempen garment to block the window.” Rain leaked through above, and damp seeped below; yet he sat upright and played his zither. *Exegesis*: “Kuāng means ‘upright.’” *Exegesis*: “Xián refers to playing and singing.”

Zigong arrived in a grand carriage, his inner robe dark blue and his outer robe white. *Exegesis*: “The inner garment was dark blue; the outer robe was white.” His high-wheeled chariot could not pass through the lane, so he went on foot to see Yuan Xian. Yuan Xian wore a hat of birch-bark and slippers without heels. *Exegesis*: “The hat was made from the bark of a birch-like tree.” Guo Qingfan comments: “The *Rhapsody on the Shanglin Park* speaks of ‘birch and maple, *ping* and *lu*.’ Zhang Yi explains: ‘*Hua* refers to bark that can be made into rope,’ meaning the tree of heaven. The *Shuowen* states: ‘The tree of heaven is a kind of wood; its bark is used to wrap pine resin. It is pronounced like *hua*.’ Li [Xun] comments: ‘Slippers without heels.’ The *Three Cang*’s *Exegesis* writes *xǐ* as *sǐ*, explaining: ‘To tread.’ The *Shenglei* sometimes writes it as *xǐ*. The *Tongsuwen* states: ‘Shoes without heels are called *xǐ*.’” He leaned on a staff of bramble and answered at the gate.

Zigong exclaimed: “Alas!”

"Master, what ails you?"

Yuan Xian answered: "I have heard it said: 'To lack wealth is called poverty; to study yet be unable to practice is called affliction.' Now I am poor, not afflicted."

Zigong hesitated, his face flushed with shame.

Yuan Xian laughed and said: "To act in hope of worldly recognition—*Exegesis*: 'To hope is to long for. One’s conduct is always swayed by the world’s praise.'—to curry favor and make friends, to study for the sake of others, to teach for one’s own sake—*Exegesis*: 'One ought to study for oneself and teach for others; now it is the reverse.'—to feign benevolence and righteousness—*Exegesis*: 'To rely on benevolence and righteousness for wickedness.'—and to adorn oneself with carriages and horses, these are things I cannot bear to do."

When Master Zeng lived in Wei, his quilted robe had no outer layer, and his complexion was rough and swollen—*Exegesis*: "Rough and swollen, chapped and cracked." Guo Qingfan comments: "Perhaps *kuài* should read *guì*, meaning gravely ill."—his hands and feet were calloused. For three days he did not kindle a fire; for ten years he did not make new clothes. When he straightened his cap, its cord snapped; when he grasped his lapel, his elbow showed; when he put on his shoes, their heels split.
