Yet he dragged his heelless slippers and sang the *Odes of Shang*, his voice filling heaven and earth, resonant as metal and stone. The Son of Heaven could not make him a subject, nor could the feudal lords befriend him.

Thus, those who cultivate the will forget the body; *Exegesis*: "The worthy and the noble do not let the body thwart the will." Those who cultivate the body forget profit; *Exegesis*: "Those who nurture life do not let profit harm their well-being." Those who attain the Dao forget the mind. *Exegesis*: "Those who attain the Dao forget the art of knowing."

Confucius said to Yan Hui: "Come back!"

"Your family is poor and your station low—why do you not seek office?"

Yan Hui replied, "I do not wish to. Beyond the outer wall, I have fifty *mu* of fields, enough to provide congee; *Exegesis*: "Some versions write this as *zhān*; the *Guangya* says, 'It means gruel.'" within the inner wall, ten *mu* of fields, enough to yield silk and hemp. Playing the zither suffices for my amusement; what I have learned of the Master’s Dao suffices for my joy. I do not wish to seek office."

Confucius’s expression changed solemnly as he said, "Excellent is Hui’s intent! I have heard it said: 'Those who know contentment do not burden themselves with profit; those who are secure in self-possession do not fear its loss—*that* is what is meant by profit. Those whose conduct is cultivated within feel no shame without rank.' I have recited these words for a long time, but only now, in Hui, do I see them realized. This is my good fortune." *Exegesis*: "He rejoiced in having found such a person."

The Duke Mou of Zhongshan said to Zhanzi, *Exegesis*: "A prince of Wei, enfeoffed at Zhongshan, named Mou." *Exegesis*: "Zhanzi was a worthy man; the *Huainanzi* writes this as *Zhan*." "Though my body dwells by rivers and seas, my heart lingers beneath the palace gates—*Exegesis*: "The *Huainanzi* writes this as *guī*. The Sima edition is the same, reading *guī* as *wei*. It refers to the towering gates of the palace, the entrance to the lord’s residence." *Exegesis*: "Xu Shen says, 'The two watchtowers of the Son of Heaven.'"—what is to be done?"

Zhanzi said, "Value life. When life is valued, profit becomes light."

*Exegesis*: "To value life is to honor it."

The Duke Mou of Zhongshan said, "Though I know this, I cannot overcome myself."

Zhanzi said, "If you cannot overcome yourself, then yield—why should the spirit resent it? *Exegesis*: "'If you cannot overcome your desires, then follow your heart’s inclinations; this does not trouble the spirit with needless aversion.'" *Exegesis*: "Yu Yue says, 'The phrase should end at *yield*. The *Lüshi Chunqiu*’s 'Shen Wei' chapter reads, 'If you cannot overcome yourself, then indulge it'; the *Wenzi*’s 'Xia De' chapter and the *Huainanzi*’s 'Dao Ying' chapter both read 'yield to it,' and the repetition of 'yield to it' makes clear that 'yield' and 'spirit' should not be read together.'"

"One who cannot overcome himself yet forcibly resists yielding—this is called double injury. A doubly injured person does not belong among the long-lived." *Exegesis*: "Read *chong* as in 'repeated.'" *Exegesis*: "Double injury means a second wound. If you cannot overcome yourself, you are already injured; to forcibly restrain yourself from yielding is to wound yourself again. The *Lüshi Chunqiu*’s commentary says, 'Read *chong* as in "repeated injury."' This is correct; the *Exegesis* is mistaken."

The Lord of Wei Mou was a prince of a state with ten thousand chariots; when he withdrew to caves and mountain hollows, it was harder for him than for a common scholar. Though he had not yet attained the Dao, he could be said to have grasped its intent.

Confucius was stranded between Chen and Cai, going seven days without cooked food, subsisting on a broth of pigweed without a grain of rice. His complexion was utterly spent, yet he still plucked his zither and sang within his chamber. As Yan Hui sorted vegetables, Zilu and Zigong said to each other, “Our Master was twice driven from Lu, his traces erased in Wei, his tree felled in Song, reduced to destitution in Shang and Zhou, besieged in Chen and Cai. Whoever kills him incurs no guilt; whoever tramples him meets no prohibition. Yet he strums and sings, never ceasing his music—does a noble man show such shamelessness?”

Yan Hui had no reply. He entered and told Confucius, who pushed aside his zither and sighed deeply, saying, “You and Ci are petty men. Summon them—I will speak to them.”

Zilu and Zigong entered. Zilu said, “This may be called destitution.” Confucius replied, “What words are these! The noble man who is at one with the Dao is called ‘at one’; he who is cut off from the Dao is called ‘destitute.’ Now I embrace the Dao of benevolence and righteousness, yet encounter the calamities of a disordered age—how can this be called destitution? Thus, when I look within, I am not cut off from the Dao; when I face adversity, I do not lose my virtue. When the cold arrives and the frost and dew descend, I thereby know the flourishing of the pine and cypress. The straits of Chen and Cai—are they not a blessing for me?”

Confucius then crisply returned to his zither and played and sang. Zilu, roused, took up his shield and danced. Zigong said, “I knew not the height of heaven nor the depth of earth.” The ancients who attained the Dao found joy in both adversity and success. What they rejoiced in was not adversity or success, but the Dao’s presence in this: then adversity and success became as the sequence of cold and heat, wind and rain.

Thus Xu You found delight by the Ying River, and Gong Bo found fulfillment at Gongshou. Shun offered the empire to his friend Beiren Wuze, who said, “How strange the lord of men! Dwelling amid the fields, yet wandering at Yao’s gate. Not content with this alone—he would further smear me with his shameful conduct. I am ashamed to see him.” Thereupon he cast himself into the Qingling Abyss.

When Tang was about to attack Jie, he consulted Bian Sui, who said, “This is not my affair.” Tang asked, “Who is suitable?” He replied, “I do not know.” Tang then consulted Mao Guang, who said, “This is not my affair.” Tang asked, “Who is suitable?” He replied, “I do not know.” Tang said, “What of Yi Yin?” Mao Guang answered, “He is strong and enduring, patient of disgrace—I know nothing else of him.” Tang then plotted with Yi Yin to attack Jie and overthrew him, offering the throne to Bian Sui. Bian Sui declined, saying, “When the lord plotted against Jie, he consulted me—he must have thought me a villain. When he overthrew Jie and offered the throne to me, he must have thought me greedy. I was born in a disordered age, and a man without the Dao has twice come to smear me with his shameful conduct. I cannot bear to hear it again.” He then cast himself into the Zhou River and died.

Tang further offered the throne to Mao Guang, saying, “The wise plan, the valiant execute, the benevolent occupy—this is the ancient Way. Why do you not take your place?” Mao Guang declined, saying, “To depose one’s superior is not righteous; to kill the people is not benevolent; others face the peril while I enjoy the gain—this is not integrity. I have heard it said: ‘One who does not act righteously does not accept its emoluments; in an age without the Dao, one does not tread its soil.’ How much less would I accept elevation! I cannot bear to see this any longer.”

He then strapped a stone to his back and drowned himself in the Lu River.

In former times, when the Zhou arose, there were two knights dwelling in Guzhu named Boyi and Shuqi. The two conferred, saying, “We have heard that in the west there is a man who seems to possess the Dao. Let us go and observe him.” When they reached Qiyang, King Wu heard of them and sent Shu Dan to meet them, making a covenant: “I shall raise your rank by two grades and appoint you to the first rank of office.” They slew an animal and buried it as an oath. The two looked at each other and laughed, saying, “Alas! How strange! This is not what we call the Dao. In the days when Shennong ruled the world, sacrifices were performed with utmost reverence yet without prayers for blessing. In his dealings with others, loyalty and trust were fully practiced without seeking anything in return. He delighted in governance and so governed; he delighted in order and so brought order—without using others’ ruin to build himself up, without using others’ lowliness to exalt himself, without exploiting the times for personal gain. Now the Zhou, seeing the disorder of Yin, has hastily seized power. They scheme above and traffic in bribes below, rely on arms to maintain their might, slaughter animals to swear oaths as pledges of trust, parade their conduct to please the masses, and resort to killing and warfare to seize advantage. This is but replacing one chaos with another. We have heard that in ancient times, a knight in an age of order did not shirk his duty, while in an age of disorder, he did not cling to mere survival. Now the world is benighted and Zhou’s virtue has declined. Rather than cling to Zhou and sully ourselves, it is better to avoid them and keep our conduct pure.” The two brothers then traveled north to Mount Shouyang, where they starved to death.

As for Boyi and Shuqi, when it came to wealth and honor, if they could be avoided, they would surely not rely on them. Upholding lofty integrity and defiant conduct, they alone delighted in their own resolve and refused to serve the world. Such was the integrity of these two knights.

Confucius and Liuxia Ji were friends.

Liuxia Ji’s younger brother was named Robber Zhi. Robber Zhi led a band of nine thousand men, rampaging across the world and assaulting the feudal lords. *Li Qi’s annotation to the* Han History *states: “Zhi was a great robber of Qin.”* Yu [Yue] comments: “*The* Correct Meaning *to the* Records of the Historian’s *‘Biography of Boyi’* states: ‘Zhi was the name of a great robber in the time of the Yellow Emperor.’ Thus, there is no definitive account of when Zhi lived. Confucius and Liuxia Hui were not contemporaries, nor were Liuxia Hui and Robber Zhi. Readers should not mistake allegory for fact.”* They broke into chambers and smashed door pivots, drove off people’s oxen and horses, and seized their wives and daughters. Greed made them forget their kin; they paid no heed to parents or brothers and did not sacrifice to their ancestors. Wherever they passed, great states manned their walls, and small states took refuge in their forts. *The* Ritual Records *Zheng [Xuan] annotation states: “A small fortified city is called a fort.”* The common people suffered greatly.

Confucius said to Liuxia Ji, “As a father, one must surely instruct his son; as an elder brother, one must surely teach his younger brother. If a father cannot instruct his son, or an elder brother cannot teach his younger brother, then the bond of father and son, elder and younger brother, is worthless. Now, sir, you are a man of talent in this age, yet your younger brother is Robber Zhi, a scourge upon the world, and you cannot teach him. I am secretly ashamed for you. Allow me to go and persuade him on your behalf.”

Liuxia Ji replied, “You say, ‘As a father, one must surely instruct his son; as an elder brother, one must surely teach his younger brother.’ But if a son does not heed his father’s instruction, or a younger brother does not accept his elder brother’s teaching, what can even your eloquence do? Moreover, Zhi’s nature is such that his mind surges like a spring, his intentions gust like the wind. His strength is sufficient to repel enemies, and his eloquence is sufficient to gloss over wrongs. He is pleased when his heart is followed and enraged when it is opposed. He is quick to insult others with words. You must not go.”

Confucius did not listen. Yan Hui drove the carriage, and Zigong stood to the right as they went to see Robber Zhi. Robber Zhi was just then resting his troops on the southern slope of Mount Tai, mincing human liver for his afternoon meal. *The* Exegesis *states: “*Bu*, according to the* Character Forest*, refers to eating at the hour of* shen *(3–5 p.m.).”*

Confucius alighted from the carriage and stepped forward. He said to the usher, “I am Kong Qiu of Lu. Having heard of the general’s lofty virtue, I respectfully bow twice to the usher.” The usher went in to announce him. When Robber Zhi heard this, he flew into a great rage. His eyes blazed like stars, and his hair bristled so that it lifted his cap. He said, “Is this not that crafty, deceitful man Kong Qiu of Lu? Tell him this: ‘You fabricate words and concoct phrases, falsely invoking Kings Wen and Wu. *That is, you claim to model yourself on Kings Wen and Wu.* You wear a cap like the branches of a tree—*Sima [Biao] comments: “The cap is excessively ornate, like the branches of a tree.”*—and gird yourself with the flanks of a dead ox—*Sima [Biao] comments: “He takes oxhide to make a large leather belt.”*—spouting a torrent of specious arguments. You eat without plowing, clothe yourself without weaving, flap your lips and wag your tongue to stir up right and wrong, deluding the rulers of the world and causing its scholars to abandon their roots. You recklessly perform filial piety and brotherly duty, hoping to attain enfeoffment and wealth. Your crimes are vast and your punishment severe.’ *Yu [Yue] comments: “*Ji *should be* ji *(‘to punish’). The* Explanation of Terms *states: “*Ji*: to execute.” That is, his crimes are great and his punishment severe. *Ji *and* ji *were interchangeable in ancient script. The* Exegesis *to the* Book of Documents’ *“Great Plan,” “Numerous Officers,”* Zuo’s *commentary to the twenty-eighth year of Duke Xi, and the seventh year of Duke Zhao all state: “*Ji *was originally written* ji. *”*—Be gone at once! If not, I shall add your liver to today’s afternoon meal!’”

Confucius again addressed the messenger: “I have been fortunate enough to gain the favor of Ji. I would be content merely to gaze upon his threshold.” *Explanation of Text* notes: “Sima’s edition has *qi* instead of *mu* (‘threshold’), glossing it: ‘He dares not look upon Zhi’s face but only upon the knots of his sandals before turning away.’” The attendant relayed this message, and Robber Zhi said: “Bring him forward!” Confucius hastened forward, then retreated from his mat and withdrew, bowing twice to Robber Zhi.

Robber Zhi flew into a rage, stretching out his legs, pressing his sword, glaring with eyes like a tiger’s, and roaring like a nursing tigress: “Come forward, Qiu! If your words please me, you shall live; if they offend me, you shall die.”

Confucius replied: “I have heard that in the world there are three kinds of *power*: to be born and grow tall, surpassingly handsome and peerless, so that young and old, high and low all delight in seeing you—this is the highest *power*; to comprehend the workings of Heaven and Earth and to discern the nature of all things—this is the middle *power*; *Explanation of Text* notes: “*Zhi* (‘to know’) is read as *zhi* (‘wisdom’).” to be brave, fierce, resolute, and daring, able to gather multitudes and lead armies—this is the lowest *power*. Anyone possessing even one of these is fit to face south and style himself a lord. Now, General, you possess all three: your body stands eight feet and two inches tall, your face and eyes radiant, your lips bright as cinnabar—*Sima* comments: “Shining.”—your teeth even as aligned shells, your voice attuned to the Yellow Bell. Yet you are called Robber Zhi. I humbly feel shame on your behalf for not claiming what is yours.

If you are willing to heed me, I shall journey south to Wu and Yue, north to Qi and Lu, east to Song and Wei, and west to Jin and Chu, to have built for you a great city of several hundred *li*, establishing a domain of hundreds of thousands of households. I shall exalt you as a feudal lord, renewing your standing in the world, disbanding your troops, resting your soldiers, gathering and nurturing your brothers, and together offering sacrifices to your ancestors.” *Gong* is read as *gong* (“to provide”). “This is the conduct of a sage and a man of talent, the very desire of all under Heaven.”

Robber Zhi flew into a rage and said: “Come forward, Qiu! Those who can be swayed by profit and admonished by words are but the foolish and base common folk. As for my tall stature and fine appearance, which delight all who see them—these are the legacy of my father and mother’s virtue. Though you do not praise me, do I not know myself? Moreover, I have heard: ‘Those who flatter to your face also slander behind your back.’ Now you offer me a great city and a multitude of people—this is but to lure me with profit and treat me as livestock among the common herd. How could such a thing endure?

Of all great domains, none surpasses the realm under Heaven. Yao and Shun ruled all beneath Heaven, yet their descendants were left without even a pin’s worth of land; Zhu and Jun did not inherit. Tang and Wu ascended as Son of Heaven, yet their posterity was extinguished. Was this not because their gain was too great?

Furthermore, I have heard: in ancient times, beasts were many and men few, so the people dwelled in nests to evade them, gathering acorns by day and roosting in trees by night—thus they were called the people of the Nest-Builder. In those days, people knew not clothing; in summer they piled firewood, and in winter they warmed themselves by it—thus they were called the people who knew life. In the age of Shennong, they lay at ease, serene in repose, and rose with unbounded grace. The people knew their mothers but not their fathers, dwelling with deer and elk, tilling for food and weaving for clothing, without any thought of harming one another—this was the height of perfect virtue. Yet even the Yellow Emperor could not sustain such virtue: he battled Chiyou on the plains of Zhuolu, and blood flowed for a hundred *li*. Yao and Shun arose, appointing ministers; Tang exiled his lord, and King Wu slew Zhou. From then on, the strong oppressed the weak, and the many tyrannized the few. Since the time of Tang and Wu, all have been followers of chaos.

Now you cultivate the Way of Wen and Wu, wielding the world’s eloquence to instruct later generations. You don flowing robes and narrow sashes, uttering deceptive words and feigning conduct to delude the lords of the world, all to seek wealth and rank—there is no greater thief than you! Why does the world not call you ‘Robber Qiu’ instead of calling me ‘Robber Zhi’?

You beguiled Zilu with honeyed words to make him follow you, causing him to cast off his warrior’s cap, lay down his long sword, and receive your teachings. The world all said, ‘Kong Qiu can quell violence and forbid wrongdoing.’ Yet in the end, Zilu sought to kill the lord of Wei and failed, his body pickled and hung upon the eastern gate of Wei—this was the failure of your teaching! Do you still call yourself a man of talent and a sage? You were twice driven from Lu, your traces erased in Wei, impoverished in Qi, besieged in Chen and Cai, and left with no refuge under Heaven. Your teaching brought Zilu to this disaster—there seems to be a missing phrase here. Above, you have no means to secure yourself; below, you have no means to secure others. Is your Way truly worthy of esteem?

Of all those the world exalts, none surpasses the Yellow Emperor, yet even he could not preserve his virtue, battling on the plains of Zhuolu until blood flowed for a hundred *li*. Yao was unfilial, Shun was undutiful—Yao did not pass the throne to Danzhu, and Shun was hated by his father. Yu was crippled—his toil in governing the waters left him afflicted. Tang exiled his lord, King Wu attacked Zhou, and King Wen was imprisoned at Youli.” The sentence should precede “King Wu” but is misplaced.

These six figures, exalted by the world—who has thoroughly examined them, who has weighed them with discernment? All were deluded by profit, losing their true nature and forcibly distorting their innate dispositions; their conduct was truly shameful!

Among those the world calls worthy gentlemen: Boyi and Shuqi renounced the rulership of Gubuzhu, yet starved to death on Mount Shouyang, their flesh and bones left unburied. Bao Jiao adorned his conduct to condemn the age, embracing a tree until he died. Shentu Di remonstrated but was not heeded, so he carried a stone and cast himself into the river, becoming food for fish and turtles. Jie Zitui was the very model of loyalty, cutting flesh from his own thigh to feed Duke Wen; later, the Duke betrayed him, and in anger, Zitui embraced a tree and burned to death. Weisheng arranged to meet a woman beneath a bridge; when she did not come and the waters rose, he refused to leave, clinging to the bridge pillar until he drowned.

These six men differed not at all from dismembered dogs, drifting swine, or beggars clutching gourds—all abandoned their reputations and treated death lightly, forgetting the root of nurturing life and the mandate of longevity. They did not reflect on the foundation of self-preservation, nor that life’s span is decreed by Heaven.

Among those the world calls loyal ministers, none surpass Prince Bigan and Wu Zixu. Zixu was drowned in the river, Bigan had his heart carved out. Though these two are hailed as loyal ministers, in the end they became laughingstocks to all under Heaven. From a higher perspective, even Zixu and Bigan—who sacrificed themselves for their states and stand above the rest—are not truly worthy of esteem.

What you, Qiu, have tried to persuade me with is like telling me of ghostly matters, which I cannot comprehend. If you speak to me of human affairs, it is nothing beyond these examples—all of which I have already heard and understood.

Now let me tell you of human nature: the eye desires to see beauty, the ear to hear sound, the mouth to savor flavor, and the will to reach fulfillment. Of men, the longest-lived may reach a hundred years, the average eighty, the shortest sixty. Yet deduct the time lost to illness, death, mourning, and sorrow—what remains?

Within this span, the days one opens their mouth to laugh scarcely exceed four or five in a month. Heaven and Earth are without end, yet human death comes at its appointed time. Holding this finite vessel and entrusting it to the boundless, it vanishes in an instant—no different from a swift steed galloping past a crack in the wall.

Those who cannot bring joy to their will and nurture their allotted years are not masters of the Dao. All that you, Qiu, have spoken are things I reject. Begone at once, return home, and speak no more of this! Your Way is frantic and grasping—"frantic" meaning the loss of one’s nature, "grasping" meaning never enough—nothing but deceit, artifice, and falsehood. It cannot preserve the integrity of the true, so why even discuss it?"

Confucius bowed twice, then hurried away. Stepping out the door, he mounted his carriage, but three times his hands fumbled the reins. His eyes stared blankly, seeing nothing; his complexion was ashen, like the dead. Leaning against the crossbar, he bowed his head, unable to catch his breath.

Upon returning to the eastern gate of Lu, he chanced upon Liuxia Ji. Liuxia Ji said, "It’s been several days since I last saw you—now your carriage and horses bear the marks of travel. Might you have gone to see Zhi?" *"Might" meaning "surely not."*

Confucius looked up to the heavens and sighed, "Yes."

Liuxia Ji asked, "Did Zhi perhaps offend you as I warned before?"—referring to Liuxia’s earlier words.

Confucius replied, "Yes."

"Master, what you’ve done is like needlessly cauterizing yourself when you’re not even ill—racing to stroke a tiger’s head, pluck its whiskers, nearly falling into its jaws!"

Zizhang asked Mangou De, "Why not act? Why not practice righteousness? Without action, there is no trust; without trust, no one will employ you; without employment, no profit. Thus, when one considers reputation and calculates advantage, true righteousness is what matters. If you take no action, people will not trust you; without trust, no one will employ you; without employment, there is no emolument. So when one considers reputation and calculates advantage, only the practice of righteousness is truly valid. If you abandon reputation and profit and turn back to your heart, then the conduct of a scholar cannot be neglected even for a day." The above refers to those who die for reputation and profit. If one abandons reputation and profit and returns to one’s own heart, the conduct of a scholar still cannot be neglected even for a day.

Mangou De replied, "The shameless grow rich, the glib rise to prominence. The greatest of reputation and profit lie in shamelessness and glibness. Thus, when one considers reputation and calculates advantage, true trustworthiness is what matters. If you abandon reputation and profit and turn back to your heart, then the conduct of a scholar should embrace the Heavenly within." When one considers reputation and calculates advantage, only trustworthiness is truly valid. If one abandons reputation and profit and returns to one’s own heart, then the conduct of a scholar should embrace the Dao of nature.

Zizhang said, "In the past, Jie and Zhou were honored as Sons of Heaven, possessing all under Heaven. If today you told a slave or bandit, ‘Your conduct is like Jie and Zhou’s,’ they would flush with shame and feel indignant—such is the contempt of petty men. Confucius and Mo Di were impoverished commoners; if today you told a prime minister, ‘Your conduct is like Confucius and Mo Di’s,’ they would change their expression and protest their inadequacy—such is the true esteem of scholars. Thus, to hold the position of Son of Heaven does not necessarily confer nobility; to be a poor commoner does not necessarily confer baseness. The distinction between noble and base lies in the beauty or ugliness of one’s conduct."

Mangou De said, "Petty thieves are imprisoned, but great thieves become feudal lords. At the gates of feudal lords, men of principle reside. These four lines also appear in the *Qièqiè* chapter, where ‘men of principle’ is written as ‘benevolence and righteousness.’ In the past, Duke Huan of Qi killed his elder brother and took his sister-in-law as his wife, yet Guan Zhong served as his minister. Tian Chengzi murdered his lord and usurped the state, yet Confucius accepted his gifts. In words, they condemn such men; in deeds, they defer to them—is this not a contradiction raging in the breast, words and actions at war? Is this not perverse? Thus, the *Book* says: ‘Who is ugly, who is beautiful? The successful are heads, the unsuccessful are tails.’"

Zizhang said, "If you take no action, kinship and affection will lack order, nobility and baseness will lack righteousness, elders and juniors will lack sequence—how then can the Five Bonds and Six Positions be distinguished?"

Mangou De replied, "Yao killed his eldest son; Shun exiled his younger brother. Was there order in kinship and affection? Tang exiled Jie; King Wu attacked Zhou. Was there righteousness in nobility and baseness? Wang Ji was the rightful heir, yet the Duke of Zhou killed his elder brother. Was there sequence in elders and juniors? The Confucians preach hypocritical words; the Mohists advocate universal love—can the Five Bonds and Six Positions be distinguished? Moreover, you rectify for the sake of reputation, while I rectify for the sake of profit. The reality of reputation and profit does not accord with principle, nor is it illuminated by the Dao. Daily, I dispute with you before Wu Yue, who says: ‘The petty man dies for wealth, the gentleman dies for reputation. Though their emotions shift and their natures change in different ways, when it comes to abandoning what they should hold and dying for what they should not, they are the same.’ Thus it is said: Do not act as a petty man; turn back and die for the Heavenly. Do not act as a gentleman; follow the principle of Heaven. Whether crooked or straight, observe the Heavenly limit—do not question crookedness or straightness, but regard your natural end as the limit. Face the four directions, ebb and flow with the times. Whether right or wrong, grasp the revolving mechanism."

"Grasp the Dao of the ring’s center to respond to right and wrong." Alone, he completes his intent, wandering with the Dao. Without fixation in action, without completion in righteousness, one will lose what one seeks to do. Without rushing toward wealth, without dying for success, one will abandon the Heavenly. Bi Gan was disemboweled, Wu Zixu had his eyes gouged out—these were the disasters of loyalty. Zhigong testified against his father, Weisheng drowned—these were the perils of trust. Baozi stood by the pole, Shenzi did not defend himself—these were the harms of integrity. Confucius did not see his mother, Kuangzi did not see his father—these were the failures of righteousness. These are what the ancients transmitted and later generations discuss, taking them as models for scholars to rectify their words and align their conduct, yet still they suffer calamity and encounter misfortune.

Wuzu asked Zhihe: "No one among men ultimately fails to pursue fame and seek profit. Those who are wealthy attract others; those who attract others are humbled; those who are humbled are honored. To be seen as humble and honored is the Way to longevity, bodily ease, and joy. Now you alone have no such intent—is your wisdom insufficient? Or do you know but lack the strength to act? Or do you persist in seeking the correct path without forgetting it?" The words *yi* and *yi* are interchangeable here, meaning "or."

Zhihe replied: "Now, such a person regards those born in the same era and dwelling in the same village as themselves as transcendent, surpassing the world—yet this is to be fixated on having no master and no standard, by which to survey the times of past and present and the divisions of right and wrong. Such a person, who pursues fame and profit, sees those of their own time and place as transcendent and surpassing the world, fixated on non-action and mastering the correct Dao, sufficient to survey the times of past and present and the divisions of right and wrong—why not emulate them? Yet they blend with the俗 and transform with the world, abandoning what is most weighty and discarding what is most honorable for the sake of their pursuits. How far this is from the Way of longevity, bodily ease, and joy! They mingle with the vulgar and conform to the world, how distant they are from those who transcend the俗 and surpass the world! Abandoning the weightiest life and discarding the most honorable Dao for the sake of what they call wealth and nobility—how far this is from the truth of things!

The pain of sorrow and the peace of contentment are not reflected in the body; the distress of fear and the delight of joy are not reflected in the heart. Knowing how to act but not knowing why one acts, one may be honored as Son of Heaven and possess all under Heaven, yet still not escape misfortune." Wuzu said: "Wealth, for a person, has no disadvantage. It exhausts beauty and perfects power, reaching what the utmost person cannot attain and what the worthy cannot surpass. It harnesses others’ courage and strength without becoming domineering, wields others’ wisdom and plans as one’s own discernment, and relies on others’ virtue to be deemed virtuous. Though not ruling a state, one is as revered as a lord or father. Moreover, sounds and sights, flavors and power—these bring joy to the heart without need of learning, and comfort to the body without need of imitation. Desires and aversions, avoidance and pursuit—these do not require a teacher, for they are human nature. Though all under Heaven may oppose me, who can reject them?" The words mean that all under Heaven shares the same desires.

Zhihe said: "The actions of the wise move with the people and do not violate their measures. The wise act for all under Heaven by moving with the people, and the people do not transgress their standards."

Thus content, one does not contend; acting without contrivance, one seeks nothing beyond. Discontent drives the search, contending in all four directions without self-regard as greed; as Master Cheng explains: "four directions, meaning all quarters." Surfeit prompts refusal, relinquishing all under Heaven without self-regard as integrity. Herein lies the division between sage and commoner. The essence of integrity and greed is not imposed from without but reflects the measure of self-examination. The essence of integrity and greed is not forced by external pressure but revealed by looking inward at one’s own capacity.

Though possessing the power of the Son of Heaven, one does not use nobility to overbear others; though wealth encompasses all under Heaven, one does not use riches to toy with others. Calculating the harm, considering the consequences—"pondering their reversal," as in the *Airs of Wei*—one deems it injurious to one’s nature, thus declines and does not accept, not to seek reputation. Yao and Shun ruled as emperors and brought harmony, the black-haired people then harmonious. Not out of benevolence toward all under Heaven, but to avoid allowing beauty to harm life; exhausting fine benefits to serve oneself is to harm one’s own life. Shanjuan and Xu You, offered the throne, declined—not out of empty refusal but to avoid allowing affairs to harm themselves. All these embraced what benefited them and declined what harmed them, and all under Heaven called them worthy. Thus one may possess such worthiness, yet they did not seek to cultivate reputation.

Wu Zu said: "To cling to reputation, torment the body, renounce sweetness, and restrict nourishment to preserve life—is this not like one long afflicted, enduring hardship without dying?" To insist on guarding reputation, tormenting the body, forsaking sweetness, and limiting sustenance to preserve life—is this not the same as one long diseased, enduring hardship without death? What benefit is there?

Zhihe said: "Equanimity brings fortune, excess brings harm—nothing is otherwise, and wealth is the most extreme case. Now the rich delight their ears with the sounds of bells, drums, pipes, and flutes, and their mouths with the flavors of fine meats and rich wines," as the *Shuowen* says: "delighting the mouth with what pleases it." This stirs their minds, causing them to neglect their affairs—one may call this disorder. Engorged with qi, they are like one bearing a heavy load while climbing—one may call this suffering. Xu’s note: "Xu reads *ai* as *wai*, fifth tone. Alternatively, *hu gai* tone. *Hai* means food reaching the throat." Wang Niansun says: "In the *Zuo Tradition*, *feng* means abundance. *Feng qi* means abundant qi." Thus, with many desires, one is choked and drowned in surging qi, like bearing a heavy load uphill—how great the suffering! Coveting wealth brings affliction, coveting power brings exhaustion. Guo Qingfan says: "In the *Miu Cheng Xun* of the *Huainanzi*, *wei* means illness. Paired with *jie*, both mean ailment." At rest, one is drowned; in comfort, one is enraged. Idle, one is steeped in indulgence; pampered, one is filled with fury—one may call this sickness. Pursuing wealth and profit, one is so full as to be deaf and unaware of avoidance, pressing forward without cease—one may call this disgrace. Wealth accumulates without use, clung to without release, filling the heart with vexation and sorrow. Seeking more without end—one may call this sorrow. Within, one fears the threats of robbers and extortion; without, one dreads the harm of bandits and thieves. Within, one encircles with layered defenses; Li says: "inner layers of towers, outer open windows—fortifications and defenses." Without, one dares not walk alone—one may call this fear. These six are the greatest harms under Heaven, yet all are neglected and unexamined. When calamity arrives, one seeks to exhaust one’s nature and deplete one’s wealth, craving wealth as if it were innate nature. Wealth becomes nature, hence "exhausting nature and depleting wealth." Yet in a single day, one cannot return to a state of innocence. Guo Songtao says: "*Dan* and *dan* are interchangeable. *Dan* means 'only'; *dan* also means 'only.'" Thus, when observed by name, it is unseen; when sought for profit, it is unattained. Twisting mind and body to contend for this—is this not delusion?

**Miscellaneous Chapters, "Discourse on Swords" (Chapter Thirty)**
Formerly, King Wen of Zhao delighted in swords. Xu’s note: "Sima says: 'King Huiwen, named He, son of King Wuling. Later than Zhuangzi by 350 years.' The *Dong Ji* states: 'In the seventeenth year of King Nan of Zhou, the first year of King Huiwen of Zhao.' Another says: According to the *Long Chronology*, King Huiwen coincided with Zhuangzi—perhaps Biao’s words are mistaken." Swordsmen thronged his gates as guests, numbering over three thousand, dueling before him day and night. Over a hundred died or were wounded each year, yet his fondness was unabated. After three years, the state declined, and the feudal lords plotted against it. The crown prince, Kui, grew anxious. Yu says: "King Huiwen was later succeeded by King Xiaocheng, named Dan, so this crown prince likely did not ascend." He summoned his attendants: "Whoever can persuade the king to cease his fondness for swordsmen shall be rewarded with a thousand gold pieces." The attendants said: "Zhuangzi would be capable." The crown prince then sent someone with a thousand gold pieces to offer Zhuangzi. Zhuangzi declined, but went with the messenger to see the crown prince, saying: "What instruction does the crown prince have for Zhou, that you bestow a thousand gold pieces?" The crown prince said: "I have heard that you, Master, are enlightened and sagely. I humbly offer a thousand gold pieces as gifts for your attendants. Since you decline, Kui dares not speak further!" Zhuangzi said: "I have heard that what the crown prince wishes from Zhou is to end the king’s fondness for swords. If I persuade the king above but contradict his wishes, or below fail to meet the crown prince’s expectations, I would face execution—how then could Zhou make use of gold? If I persuade the king above and meet the crown prince’s expectations below, what in Zhao could not be obtained?"

The crown prince said: "Indeed. What my king sees are only swordsmen." Zhuangzi said: "Good. Zhou is skilled in the sword." The crown prince said: "Yet all the swordsmen my king sees have unkempt hair, bristling sideburns, and drooping caps—*as if leaning forward to fight*—with coarse, unadorned tassels, short-backed robes—*for ease of movement*—and glaring eyes, their speech halting—*for the rage of warriors chokes their words*. The king delights in them. Now if you, Master, appear before the king in scholar’s robes, the matter will surely go awry."

Zhuangzi said: "Permit me to prepare swordsman’s attire." After three days preparing his sword attire, he appeared before the crown prince. The crown prince then presented him to the king, who awaited with bared blade. Zhuangzi entered the palace gate without haste and did not bow upon seeing the king. The king said: "What instruction do you bring, that you send the crown prince ahead?"—*meaning, that the crown prince spoke for him first*. Zhuangzi said: "I have heard that Your Majesty delights in the sword, so I come before you with the sword."

The king said: "What power does your sword possess to restrain and control?" Zhuangzi said: "My sword cuts down one man every ten paces and leaves no obstacle in a thousand *li*."—*Within ten paces, it slays one man; over a thousand *li*, it meets no resistance, its edge unblunted, its force unbroken—this describes the sword’s keen edge. Here, "leaves no obstacle" refers to the sword, not to men.*

The king was greatly pleased and said: "There is none in the world who can withstand it!" Zhuangzi said: "The way of the sword is to present emptiness, offer advantage, strike after the opponent moves, and arrive first. I wish to demonstrate this."—*To forget the self and remain empty, to open the way for others’ benefit, to respond only after being moved, and to perceive things before they arise—this is Zhuangzi’s use of the sword.*

The king said: "Master, rest in your quarters and await my command. I shall arrange a contest and invite you." The king then assembled swordsmen for seven days; over sixty were killed or wounded. Five or six remained, and these he stationed at the palace steps bearing their swords, then summoned Zhuangzi.

The king said: "Today, let us test the swordsmen’s skill in combat."—*"Dun" means "to order" or "to arrange."* Zhuangzi said: "I have long awaited this." The king said: "What length of weapon do you wield?"—*"Yu" means "to use."*—*Here, "weapon" refers to what one holds.*

Zhuangzi said: "Any length I am given will suffice. Yet I possess three swords, and only Your Majesty may choose which to employ. Permit me to describe them first, then demonstrate." The king said: "I wish to hear of the three swords."

Zhuangzi said: "There is the Son of Heaven’s sword, the feudal lord’s sword, and the commoner’s sword."

The king said: "What is the Son of Heaven’s sword like?"

Zhuangzi said: "The Son of Heaven’s sword has Yanxi and Stone Citadel as its point, Qi and Mount Tai as its edge—*Yanxi is a place in Yan; the edge is the sword’s blade, or alternatively, its ridges*—Jin and Wei as its spine, Zhou and Song as its guard—*the guard is the ring; Jin and Wei lie near Zhao, so they form the spine; Zhou and Song lie to the south, so they form the ring*—Han and Wei as its hilt—*the hilt is the grip; one text reads "jiá," meaning the same. Another says the guard runs from ridge to back, while the hilt runs from ridge to edge*—encompassing the Four Barbarians, enfolding the Four Seasons—*embracing the Four Barbarians with virtue and power, following the Four Seasons in generation and transformation*—encircling the Bohai Sea, girded by Mount Chang—*these two lines should precede "encompassing the Four Barbarians."*—governing by the Five Phases, judging by punishment and virtue—*punishment is penalty; virtue is reward; both are spoken of in terms of the sword. The ancients had discourses on the sword*—opening with yin and yang, sustaining with spring and summer, acting with autumn and winter—*spring and summer nurture, so one sustains without wielding; autumn and winter are stern and killing, so one acts with them.*

This sword, when thrust forward, meets no resistance; when raised, nothing is above it; when pressed down, nothing is below it; when swung, nothing lies beside it. Above, it cleaves the floating clouds; below, it severs the earth’s foundations. When this sword is once employed, it rectifies the feudal lords, and the world submits. This is the Son of Heaven’s sword."

King Wen, bewildered, lost himself and said: "What is the feudal lord’s sword like?"

Zhuangzi said: "The feudal lord’s sword has the wise and brave as its point, the pure and incorrupt as its edge, the worthy and good as its spine, the loyal and sagely as its guard, the heroic and outstanding as its hilt. This sword, when thrust forward, also meets no resistance; when raised, nothing is above it; when pressed down, nothing is below it; when swung, nothing lies beside it. Above, it models the round heaven to follow the Three Luminaries; below, it models the square earth to follow the Four Seasons; in the center, it harmonizes the people’s will to pacify the Four Directions—*the Four Directions are like the four quarters.* When this sword is once employed, it strikes like thunder, and within the four borders, none fail to submit and heed the ruler’s command. This is the feudal lord’s sword."

The king said: "What is the commoner’s sword like?"

Zhuangzi said: "The commoner’s sword has unkempt hair, bristling sideburns, and drooping caps, coarse tassels, short-backed robes, glaring eyes, and halting speech. They strike at one another before you, severing necks above and piercing livers and lungs below."

This commoner’s sword differs not from cockfighting—once the life is cut short, it serves no purpose in the affairs of state. Now Your Majesty holds the position of the Son of Heaven yet delights in the commoner’s sword; your servant humbly deems this unworthy of you.”

The king then led him up to the hall. The royal chef presented the meal, and the king circled it three times, unable to sit still. Zhuangzi said: “May Your Majesty settle into your seat and calm your qi; the matter of the sword has now been concluded.”

Thereupon, King Wen did not leave the palace for three months, and all the swordsmen submitted and perished where they stood. Sima Biao notes: “Enraged at not being received with due ceremony, they all took their own lives.”

---

**Miscellaneous Chapters: "The Old Fisherman" (Chapter Thirty-One)**

Confucius wandered through the forest of black curtains, which Sima Biao identifies as a grove of dark trees. He rested and sat upon the Apricot Terrace, which Sima Biao describes as a raised mound in the marsh. His disciples read aloud, while Confucius plucked the strings and sang, playing the zither. The melody was not yet half-finished when an old fisherman descended from his boat and approached. His beard and brows were snowy white, his hair unbound, and his sleeves swaying as he walked. Ascending from the marshland, he stopped at the edge of the dry ground, his left hand resting on his knee and his right hand supporting his chin, listening intently.

When the music ended, he beckoned to Zilu and Zigong, who both responded. The stranger pointed at Confucius and asked: “What is that man doing?” Zilu replied: “He is a gentleman of Lu.” The stranger inquired about his lineage, and Zilu answered: “His clan is Kong.” The stranger asked: “And what does the Kong clan govern?”—meaning, what arts or teachings do they practice?

Zilu did not answer, but Zigong replied: “The Kong clan cultivates loyalty and trust as their nature, embodies benevolence and righteousness in their conduct, refines ritual and music, and selects from human relationships, examining and choosing among them. Above, they serve the rulers of the age with loyalty; below, they transform the common people—Li Yun explains *qi* as ‘equal,’ while Xu Shen glosses it as ‘the people of equal standing’—all to benefit the world. This is what the Kong clan governs.”

The stranger then asked: “Is he a lord with lands?” Zigong said: “No.” “Is he an advisor to kings and marquises?” Zigong said: “No.” The stranger laughed and turned to leave, saying: “Benevolent he may be, but I fear he will not escape harm to himself. He wearies his heart and exhausts his form, endangering his true nature. Alas, how far he has strayed from the Dao!”

The *Cheng* commentary states: “This means ‘separated from the profound Dao.’” The *Shiwen* notes: “Alternatively written as *jie*. Sima [Biao] says: ‘It means *separated*.’”

Zigong returned and reported to Confucius. Confucius pushed aside his zither and rose, saying: “He must be a sage!” He then went down to seek him, reaching the marsh’s edge just as the stranger was propping up his oar to pull his boat. Sima [Biao] notes: “*Na* means *oar*, pronounced *yu*.” Turning, the stranger saw Confucius and faced him, standing still. The *Shiwen* notes: “*Xiang* is sometimes written as *toward*.” Confucius retreated, then bowed twice and stepped forward.

The stranger asked: “What do you seek?”

Confucius replied: “Earlier, Master, you left with words unfinished. Yu [Shi] notes: “*Xu* means *remnant*; you departed before completing them, hence *remnant words*. I, Qiu, am unworthy and did not grasp their meaning. I humbly awaited in your wake, hoping to hear even the sound of your cough or spittle, that I might fully assist Qiu!” The *Cheng* commentary states: “To aid what I cannot reach.”

The stranger exclaimed: “Ah! How earnest is your love of learning!”

Confucius bowed twice and rose, saying: “From my youth I have cultivated learning, and now at sixty-nine years of age, I have yet to hear the ultimate teaching. How dare I not empty my heart!”

The stranger replied: “Like follows like, sound responds to sound—this is the inherent principle of Heaven. I shall now set forth what I possess and order your *so-that*. Sima [Biao] notes: ‘*Jing* means *to order*.’ Below the same. Your *so-that* is human affairs. The Son of Heaven, the feudal lords, the great officers, and the common people—when these four are rightly aligned, each holds to its place. This is the beauty of good governance. When the four depart from their places, no disorder is greater.

When offices govern their duties and people attend to their tasks, none encroaches upon another. Cheng [Xuanying] notes: ‘*Ling* means *disorder*.’ Thus, fields lying fallow and houses exposed, clothing and food insufficient, taxes and levies unmet, wives and concubines discordant, elders and juniors without order—these are the worries of the common people. Inability to fulfill one’s post, official duties neglected, conduct impure, subordinates idle and remiss, achievements unrecognized, no merit for the state, no reputation among the people, rank and emolument unsustained—unable to preserve one’s rank and emolument—these are the worries of the great officers. The court lacking loyal ministers, the state in darkness and chaos, artisans unskilled, tribute and service unrefined, spring and autumn audiences delayed or disordered—*Shiwen* notes: ‘Failing to meet the proper sequence in court appearances’—and disobedience to the Son of Heaven: these are the worries of the feudal lords. Yin and yang disharmonious, cold and heat untimely, harming the myriad things, feudal lords violent and disorderly, seizing and attacking one another to ravage the people, rites and music unregulated, resources exhausted, human relations unordered, the hundred clans licentious and chaotic: these are the worries of the Son of Heaven’s officials.

Now, you possess neither the authority of lord or official above nor the office of a great minister below, yet you presume to regulate rites and music, select human relations, and transform the common people. Is this not excessively meddlesome? Moreover, people have eight flaws, and affairs have four perils—these cannot go unexamined.

To take up what is not one’s affair is called *zong*. Cheng notes: ‘*Zong* means *excess*.’ To advance without regard is called *flattery*. Cheng notes: ‘Unsought, one forces forth loyal words.’ To speak in hope of favor is called *sycophancy*. Cheng notes: ‘Hoping to align with another’s intent, one guides their words.’ To speak without discerning right or wrong is called *obsequiousness*. Cheng notes: ‘Indiscriminately following others, not distinguishing right from wrong.’ To delight in speaking of others’ faults is called *slander*. To sever friendships and divide kin is called *treachery*. To praise with deceit and ruin the wicked is called *depravity*—deceitfully praising while ruining those one deems wicked is called *treacherous depravity*. Yao [Shun] notes: ‘The Zhang edition reads *evil* as *virtue*, meaning “to invert right and wrong to ruin another’s virtue,” which is more incisive.’ To accommodate both good and bad, maintaining an agreeable countenance, stealthily seizing what one desires, is called *perfidy*. *Shiwen* notes: ‘*Liang rong jia shi* means to accept both good and evil, adjusting one’s demeanor accordingly. *Jia* may also be written as *yan*.’”

“To *stealthily seize* means to subtly draw forth the desires in others’ hearts.”

These eight flaws externally confuse others and internally harm oneself; the noble person does not befriend such men, nor does the enlightened ruler employ them.

The so-called four perils are these: to delight in managing great affairs, altering and overturning the constant, all to grasp at merit and fame, is called *presumptuousness*. To alter the constant norms in hopes of securing merit and fame is *reckless presumption*. To monopolize knowledge and arrogate affairs, encroaching on others and acting on one’s own, is called *greed*. To monopolize knowledge means to claim, “I alone know.” To persist in error without correction, and to grow more obstinate upon hearing admonition, is called *obduracy*. To approve only what accords with oneself, and to reject even what is good if it differs from oneself, is called *arrogance*. These are the four perils.

Only when one has shed the eight flaws and refrained from the four perils may one begin to be taught.”

Confucius sighed deeply, bowed twice, and rose, saying, “I was twice driven from Lu, my traces erased in Wei, my tree felled in Song, and I was besieged in Chen and Cai. I do not know what fault I committed to incur these four slanders—why was this?”

The guest’s expression turned sorrowful as he replied, “How difficult it is for you to awaken! There was a man who feared his shadow and loathed his footprints, and so ran to escape them. The more he lifted his feet, the more footprints he made; the faster he ran, the more his shadow clung to him. Believing himself still too slow, he ran without cease until he collapsed and died. He did not know that by standing in the shade he could rest his shadow, and by remaining still he could still his footprints—how foolish he was!

You scrutinize the space between benevolence and righteousness, examine the boundary between sameness and difference, observe the shifts of activity and stillness, adjust the measure of giving and receiving, order the passions of like and dislike, and harmonize the rhythms of joy and anger—yet you have barely escaped calamity. If this is how you weigh your dealings with things, and still you have barely escaped, then take care to cultivate yourself, guard your true nature, and return things to others, and you will have no entanglements. If you do not contend with external things, you will have no perils or burdens. Now, instead of cultivating yourself, you seek from others—is this not misplaced?”

Confucius sighed and said, “May I ask what is meant by *the true*?”

The guest replied, “The true is the utmost of essence and sincerity. Without essence and sincerity, one cannot move others. Thus, forced weeping, though sorrowful, is not mournful; forced anger, though stern, is not awe-inspiring; forced affection, though smiling, is not harmonious. True sorrow is mournful without sound; true anger is awe-inspiring before it erupts; true affection is harmonious before it smiles. When the true resides within, the spirit stirs without—this is why the true is prized.

Applied to human principles—*principles* meaning human relations—serving parents yields compassion and filial piety; serving a lord yields loyalty and integrity; drinking wine yields joy; observing mourning yields grief. Loyalty and integrity take merit as their aim; drinking wine takes joy as its aim; mourning takes grief as its aim; serving parents takes comfort as its aim. The beauty of merit’s completion lies not in uniformity of form. Many are the visible achievements, so their traces are not uniform. Serving parents with comfort does not depend on means: sipping beans and drinking water can bring perfect joy, so one does not inquire into the means. Drinking wine with joy does not require fine fare: the dishes need not be chosen. Mourning with grief does not depend on ritual: facing death with full sorrow, one then observes ritual. Ritual is what the common customs devise; the true is what one receives from Heaven, naturally and unalterably. Thus the sage models Heaven and prizes the true, not bound by custom. The foolish do the opposite: unable to model Heaven, they fret over human affairs, ignorant of the true’s value. They drift along, shaped by custom, and so are never sufficient.”

*Shiwen* notes: “*Lulu* [禄禄]: Sima’s edition reads *lulu* [录录].”
*Annotation*: *Lulu* is like *lulu* [录录]. The *Hanshu*’s eulogy for Xiao and Cao writes “lulu” [录录], and Yan’s commentary says: “Like *lulu* [鹿鹿], meaning among the common herd.”

“Alas! You were immersed in human falsity early on and only now hear of the great Dao!”

Confucius bowed twice and rose again, saying, “Today I have been so fortunate as to meet you, as if by Heaven’s grace. You do not disdain to treat me like a servant or follower, and you instruct me in person. May I ask where you reside? I would like to follow you to complete my study of the great Dao.”

The guest said, “I have heard: those who can journey with you, journey with them to the wondrous Dao. *Cheng* notes: ‘To go from delusion to awakening is *journeying*. The wondrous Dao is the true origin.’ Those who cannot journey with you do not understand the Dao—take care not to accompany them, and you will avoid blame. Strive on! I leave you now, I leave you now.”

The guest then poled his boat away, gliding along the reeds. Yan Yuan turned the carriage, while Zilu handed the reins, but Confucius did not look back. Only when the water’s ripples stilled—*Shiwen* notes: “The boat’s passage stirs the waves; when it departs far, the waves settle”—and the sound of the oar vanished did he dare to board.

Zilu stepped beside the carriage and asked, “I have long served you, yet never have I seen you meet another with such reverence. Even the lords of ten thousand chariots or the rulers of a thousand, when they receive you, do not fail to observe proper courtesy, yet you still maintain a dignified bearing. Now this fisherman leans on his oar and stands defiantly, yet you bow deeply, your waist bent like a chime stone, answering him with repeated bows—*Cheng* notes: “You received his words with bows and responded.” Is this not excessive? The disciples all find your conduct strange. How has this fisherman earned such deference?”

Confucius leaned on the crossbar and sighed, “How deeply entrenched your ways are, You! You have been steeped in ritual and propriety for so long, yet your crude and vulgar heart remains unchanged to this day. Come closer! Let me tell you.”

"To meet one’s elders without reverence is a breach of ritual; to encounter the worthy without esteem is a want of humaneness. If he were not a perfected person, he could not humble himself before others—*Cheng* notes: 'Were he not a person of utmost virtue, he could not make others yield in humility.' Yet if one’s humility is not refined, one cannot attain their truth—for as stated above, 'Truth is the utmost of pure sincerity.' Thus, to slight one’s elders harms the self. Alas! There is no greater misfortune for a person than the want of humaneness, and yet You alone clings to it—*to cling* meaning to claim it as one’s own.

Moreover, the Dao is that from which the myriad things arise. Those creatures that lose it perish; those that attain it live. In affairs, to act against it is to fail; to follow it is to succeed. Thus, wherever the Dao is found, the sage honors it. Now this fisherman’s Dao may be said to exist—how could I dare not revere him!"

---

When Liezi of *Lie Yukou* traveled to Qi, he turned back midway and encountered Bohun Maoren. (See *Liezi* in the *Huangdi* chapter.) Bohun Maoren asked, "Why have you returned along the way?" *Li* notes: "Fang means 'way.'" Liezi replied, "I was startled." Bohun Maoren said, "..."

"Why were you startled?" Liezi replied, "Once, I dined among ten *jiang*-sellers—*Li* notes: '*Jiang* is read as fermented drink. Ten households all sold fermented drinks.' *Case*: The *Huangdi* chapter reads 'fermented drink.'—yet five of them offered me gifts first." *Exegesis*: "Gifts, meaning bestowals. That is, five of the ten households first presented me with offerings." *Case*: Zhang Zhan’s commentary: "People all deferred to one beneath them." Bohun Maoren said, "If this is so, then why were you startled?"

Liezi answered, "For within, sincerity remained unresolved—*Guo* notes: 'Outwardly, he adorned himself with pride.' *Case*: The phrasing is incomplete. Zhang’s commentary includes five additional characters: 'inwardly, it was not released.'—while without, deceptive appearances formed a radiant display—*Guo* notes: 'His conduct was obsequious and polished, presenting a luminous demeanor.' *Exegesis*: 'Deceptive' is read *die* (徒協反). *Guo* says: 'obsequious.' *Shuowen* states: 'composure.'—thereby overawing others’ minds with externals—*Zhang*’s commentary: 'Outwardly, he relied on stern dignity to subdue others, though inwardly he was insufficient.'—causing people to slight the esteemed and the elderly—*Exegesis*: 'Meaning they honored Lie Yukou above elders.'—and disrupting what they took to be troublesome—*Exegesis*: '*Disrupting* is read *zā* (子兮反), meaning chaos.' *Su Yu* notes: 'The later reference to "undertaking tasks and demonstrating merit" refers to what they deemed troublesome. That is, he would use his own concerns to agitate and disorder them. *Zhuangzi* often employs *qi* (其) in this manner. Below, "Why not once observe his excellence" also refers to Ruo Ao speaking of himself.' *Xuan* notes: '*Disrupting* carries the sense of brewing. Another interpretation: *zā* is interchangeable with *jī* (赍), meaning to deliver. Both readings are valid.'"

"Now, selling millet is merely a trade for broth, a surplus of excess—*Huangdi* chapter has 'no' before 'excess.' *Zhang* notes: 'One edition omits "no."' *Case*: Without 'no,' it aligns with the *Zhuang* edition; including 'no' makes the reasoning more complete. Its profit is meager, its influence slight, and yet it is thus—how much more so for the lord of ten thousand chariots! His body toils for the state, his knowledge exhausts itself in affairs." (The *Huangdi* chapter omits '乎.' These two phrases pertain to the Qi ruler's speech.)

"He will task me with duties and demand merit from me." *Cheng* notes: "Testing me with achievements." "This is why I am alarmed." (He went to see the Qi ruler, who would assign tasks and measure merit, imposing heavy expectations—likely to bring disorder. Thus, he extrapolated from the millet-selling affair, took alarm, and fled.)

Bo Hun Mouren said: "Excellent observation! You discern human nature well. Remain at home, and others will come to you." *Sima* notes: "Preserve, meaning to attach." *Case*: Stay at home, and people will gather to you.

Soon after, he went again, and the shoes outside the door filled the entryway." *Cheng* notes: "Once he ascended the hall, many sought his instruction."

Bo Hun Mouren stood facing north, planting his staff and propping his chin upon it." *Sima* notes: "Dun means to stand upright." *Cheng* notes: "Leaning on his staff to support his chin, he listened to his words." After standing for a while, he departed without a word." *Cheng* notes: "He left in wordless return."

The attendant informed Liezi." *Shiwen* notes: "Bin, also written as 傧, refers to the one who receives guests." Liezi grasped his shoes, ran barefoot, and reached the gate, saying: "Master, since you have come, have you no medicine to offer?" *Shiwen* notes: "Sima’s edition has '废' for '发.'" *Guo Qingfan* notes: "'发' and '废' were interchangeable in ancient pronunciation." *Case*: The *Huangdi* chapter has '废.' *Zhang* notes: "'废' means to set aside. He never once offered a healing word as medicine."

He replied: "Enough!"

"Indeed, I had told you, 'Others will protect you,' and now they truly have protected you. It was not that you could make others protect you, nor could you prevent them from protecting you—so why then this pretense of marvel, this premature display of difference? The *Huangdi* chapter adds '感也' after '之' and omits '也' after '异.' *Zhang* notes: 'What art did you use to move others so?' But this is mistaken. The *Huangdi* chapter should read: 'Why do you use this to move others?' *Zhang*'s interpretation is wrong. 'Premature display of difference' means bestowing favors before others act, offering them early to move their hearts—this is merely making oneself different. There must be some influence, yet it shakes your fundamental nature—what meaning does this hold?

The *Huangdi* chapter writes '且必' for '必且,' adds '也' after '感,' and uses '身' for '才.' *Case*: '本才' means one’s fundamental nature, akin to Mencius’ '非才之罪也.' *Shiwen* notes: 'One version writes 性 for 才,' which carries the same meaning. To say one must bestow favors to move others is to chase after things, shaking your fundamental nature—what purpose does this serve?

Among those who keep your company, none admonishes you." *Xuan* notes: "None offers loyal counsel." "Their petty words are all poison to others." *Zhang* notes: "Petty words, clever and subtle, easily move people, thus becoming a poison to them." No one awakens, no one perceives—how can they examine one another? *Guo Songtao* notes: "The *Hanshu*’s *Biography of Jia Yi* reads '日夜念此至孰也,' with *Yan* annotating: '孰 means to examine closely.' Since there is no awakening or perception, how can anyone scrutinize another?"

The cunning labor, the wise worry; the incapable seek nothing. They eat their fill and wander freely, drifting like an untethered boat—empty, they wander at ease." *Cheng* notes: "Things inevitably exhaust themselves through cleverness and knowledge. Only the sage, unbound and drifting like an empty boat, roams in carefree spontaneity." *Case*: The passage from "The cunning labor" onward was added by Zhuangzi.

In the land of Qiu, a man of Zheng named Huan groaned in pain." *Sima* notes: "Huan is the man’s name." *Shiwen* notes: "Qiu is a place name." *Guo* notes: "Groaning refers to chanting and recitation." After three years, Huan became a Confucian." *Guo* notes: "祗 means 'then.'" "His learning moistened nine *li* of the riverbank and nourished three clans." *Xuan* notes: "This illustrates how, once learning is complete, it must benefit others." He sent his younger brother to study Mohism. Huan sent his brother to study Mohism. His brother’s name appears below.

The Confucians and Mohists debated, and their father sided with Di." *Cheng* notes: "Confucians follow the rites of Wen and Wu, tracing their lineage to Yao and Shun, clinging stubbornly to their doctrines and fond of verbose arguments. Mohists follow the Way of Yu, valuing frugality and generosity. With Confucianism and Mohism on divergent paths, each clinging to their own rightness, the father, like a child taking sides, aided Di." After ten years, Huan took his own life.

His father dreamed of him, who said: 'The one who made your son a Mohist was I. Why did you never examine his excellence? He has already become the fruit of the autumn cypress!'" *Case*: 阖 is interchangeable with 盍, meaning "why not." 胡 also means "why." "阖胡" used together follows the example of ancient phrases like "尚犹" or "惟独," forming a reduplicative expression. 尝 means "to try." *Shiwen* notes: "良 may also be written as 埌, pronounced *lang*, meaning a burial mound." *Case*: In the dream, Huan told his father: "Your son’s Mohism was my doing. Why did you never examine my grave? The autumn cypress I planted there has already borne fruit!" The grievance of the wronged spirit was deeply conveyed.

When the Creator repays a person, it does not repay the person but the person’s Heaven." *Guo* notes: "From here onward, these are Zhuangzi’s words." *Cheng* notes: "The Creator is the boundless, the one who fashions and transforms all things, hence called the Creator. A person’s intelligence and abilities are endowed by this creative power, not acquired through study. Thus, if one borrows from learning to assist the Dao of nature, the repayment goes to one’s Heavenly nature, not to human effort. Di possessed a Mohist nature, which did not come from Huan. Huan claimed he taught it—was this not absurd?" It was Heaven that made him so. He had a Mohist nature, so it made him a Mohist.

Yet this man believed himself to be different from others, thus despising his kin." *Cheng* notes: "Huan, relying on his own learning and cultivation, believed himself superior to ordinary people, thus belittling his kin and opposing his father." The well of the people of Qi: those who drank from it fought one another. Thus it is said: "People today are all like Huan." When the people of Qi dug a well, travelers drew water to drink, but the well’s owner, guarding the water, would seize the drinkers by the head, unaware of the spring’s natural abundance. This illustrates how Huan, ignorant of Di’s innate Mohist nature, grew resentful of him. (This note incorporates *Lu* and *Cheng*.)

From this, those with virtue do so through unknowing—how much more so those with the Dao!" *Shiwen* notes: "知 is pronounced *zhi*." *Case*: 以 and 已 are interchangeable. 德 means "to attain." To speak of attaining virtue as "virtue" and then to assert one’s own virtue is already unwise—how much less can those with the Dao fail to follow their Heavenly nature! In ancient times, this was called the punishment of fleeing from Heaven. The earlier passage states, "The cunning labor and the wise worry"—this is to be punished by Heaven. The *Dechongfu* chapter says: "Punished by Heaven, how can one escape?" If one does not claim the Dao for oneself, one may evade the punishment of fleeing from Heaven. This saying also appears in the *Yangshengzhu* chapter.

The sage finds peace in what is naturally peaceful and does not seek peace in what is not peaceful." *Cheng* notes: "安 means to follow."

The sage entrusts the natures of all living beings to themselves, neither drawing things to follow his own will nor forcibly imposing what their natures lack—this is what makes him a sage. The multitude, however, finds peace in what is not peaceful and does not find peace in what is peaceful. Abandoning the self to follow things is to find peace in what is not peaceful; not finding peace in one’s inherent allotment is not to find peace in what is peaceful.

Zhuangzi said: "Knowing the Dao is easy; refraining from speech is difficult." *Cheng* notes: "Applying knowledge is easy; forgetting speech is difficult." To know and not speak is to proceed toward Heaven; *zhi* means "to go." *Cheng* notes: "To reach the realm of naturalness." To know and to speak is to proceed toward man. The ancients proceeded toward Heaven and not toward man. *Cheng* notes: "The true men of old, those who knew the Dao, were naturally pure and free of human sentiments."

Zhu Pingman studied dragon-slaying from Zhi Liyi. *Sima* notes: "Zhu Pingman and Zhi Liyi were both men’s names." He exhausted a thousand gold’s worth of family wealth, and after three years, his skill was perfected—yet he had no use for his craft. *Xuan* notes: "There were no dragons to slay. Thus, the noble man does not value extreme skills but values the Way of the Mean."

The sage takes what is necessarily so as if it were not necessarily so, and thus has no use for arms. *Guo* notes: "Though a principle is necessarily so, to treat it as if it were not is the height of compliance—how could arms arise!" The multitude takes what is not necessarily so as necessarily so, and thus arms abound. *Xuan* notes: "Taking what is not necessarily so in principle and each insisting on their own partial views, discord and strife arise." Following arms, their actions are driven by desire. *Xuan* notes: "Yielding to strife, they act only to satisfy their wants." Arms, once grasped, bring ruin. Even with arms, one cannot rely on them.

The petty man’s knowledge does not extend beyond bribes and letters. *Xuan* notes: "*Bao* means wrapping; *ju* means padding. The *Mao Commentary* to the *Book of Songs* states: 'Those who gift fruit must wrap and pad it.'" *Sima* notes: "*Gandu* refers to bamboo slips used for writing letters of inquiry or gifts." They exhaust their spirit on trivial and shallow matters, yet wish to universally guide all things, to unify form and emptiness. Those who do so are deluded by the cosmos, burdened by form, and do not know the Great Beginning. Laboring over trivial and shallow affairs, they seek to lead all things to universal accomplishment, to empty forms to accord with the principle of the Great Unity—yet they are already deluded by the myriad forms of the cosmos, how could they know the wondrous principle of the Great Beginning?

The perfected man, however, returns his spirit to the Beginningless and delights in the darkness of the Land of Nothing-Whatsoever. *Guo* notes: "The Beginningless is the wondrous root; the Land of Nothing-Whatsoever is the realm of the Dao." *Yu* notes: "*Xisheng* reads: '*Ming* is also written as *mian*. It is also pronounced *mian*.' This is correct. *Ming* and *mian* are ancient and modern forms of the same character. The *Wen Xuan*’s *Yangsheng Lun* states: 'Staying awake until dawn without *mian*,' and Li’s commentary notes: '*Mian* is the ancient form of *mian*.' Thus, *ganming* means *ganmian*. The *Xu Wugui* chapter states: 'Sunshu Ao slept sweetly while holding a feather fan.' *Ganmian* and *ganmian* share the same meaning. The *Huainanzi*’s *Chuzhen Xun* states: 'Delighting in sleep in the realm of filth and mire,' which is based on this passage." Water flows where there is no form and vents into the Great Purity. *Xuan* notes: "Emerging from emptiness, returning to emptiness." *Case*: This is a metaphor for the perfected man’s natural flow.

Alas! You take your knowledge to be in the tip of a hair, yet do not know the Great Peace! *You* refers to the "petty man" above. The Great Peace is the realm of effortless tranquility. This speaks of seeing the small and missing the great.

There was a man of Song named Cao Shang who was sent by the King of Song as an envoy to Qin. On his departure, he received several carriages; the King of Qin was pleased with him and added a hundred more carriages. Returning to Song, he saw Zhuangzi and said: "Living in a poor alley, weaving sandals in straitened circumstances, with a withered neck and sallow face—this is what I am not good at. Awakening a ruler of ten thousand chariots and following him with a hundred carriages—this is what I am good at." Zhuangzi said: "When the King of Qin was ill, he summoned physicians. Those who lanced boils and drained abscesses received one carriage; those who licked his hemorrhoids received five carriages. The baser the treatment, the more carriages they received. Did you perhaps treat his hemorrhoids? How else did you receive so many carriages? Begone!"

Duke Ai of Lu asked Yan He, saying: "I regard Confucius as the mainstay of the state—will our country then recover?" Yan He replied: "It is perilous, verging on collapse! Confucius adorns feathers and paints them, busying himself with florid words, taking branches for the trunk, treating branch-words as the true purport. He suppresses his nature to display himself before the people, yet knows not how to be trusted. What he receives in his heart, he governs with his spirit—how could this suffice to lead the people! Is he fit for you? Or do you rely on him for sustenance? Even if this were a mistake, it might still be excusable. But now he causes the people to depart from substance and learn falsity—this is no way to guide them. Considering the future, it would be better to cease and not employ him. The state will be difficult to govern."

What is bestowed upon others yet not forgotten is not the Heavenly dispensation. Merchants and traders are held in contempt; though one may engage with them in affairs, in spirit they are still not deemed worthy. Now, to bestow virtue in this manner—how does it differ from commerce?

External punishments are meted out with metal and wood; internal punishments arise from movement and transgression. When petty men incur external punishments, metal and wood interrogate them; when they incur internal punishments, yin and yang consume them. Only the true person can escape both external and internal punishments.

Confucius said: "The human heart is more treacherous than mountains and rivers, harder to know than Heaven. Heaven at least has its seasons—spring, autumn, winter, summer, dawn, and dusk—but man has a thick exterior and deep emotions. Thus, some appear earnest yet are arrogant, some seem incompetent yet are truly capable, some are compliant yet sharp and perceptive, some are resolute yet lax, and some are leisurely yet fierce. Therefore, those who rush toward righteousness as if parched may flee from it as if scorched. Thus, the noble person sends them far to observe their loyalty, for distance breeds deceit; brings them near to observe their reverence, for proximity breeds familiarity; burdens them with tasks to observe their ability; suddenly questions them to observe their knowledge; sets urgent deadlines to observe their trustworthiness; entrusts them with wealth to observe their benevolence; warns them of danger to observe their integrity; intoxicates them with wine to observe their decorum; and mingles them in mixed company to observe their demeanor. When all nine tests are complete, even the unworthy reveal themselves."

Zheng Kaofu, upon his first appointment, bent his back; upon his second, stooped; upon his third, bowed low, creeping along the wall—who would dare to act improperly toward him!

As for the common man, upon his first appointment, he stretches himself tall; upon his second, he dances atop his chariot; upon his third, he names others as his uncles—who could compare him to Yao or Xu! The greatest harm lies in virtue having intent and intent having lashes. When virtue has intent, it is no longer natural; when intent has lashes, it is as if riddled with apertures—what ruin could be greater! And when it has lashes, one turns inward to examine, scrutinizing endlessly within the square inch of the heart. Such inward scrutiny leads only to defeat, for it brings the harm of endless distraction.

There are five perilous virtues, with the central virtue foremost—referring to the ear, eye, mouth, nose, and heart, with the heart as chief. What is the central virtue? It is that by which one esteems oneself while disparaging what one does not do. Utter destitution has eight extremes, while success has three certainties, and the body has six repositories.

Beauty, refinement, height, grandeur, strength, splendor, courage, and boldness—when all eight surpass others, one falls into ruin through reliance on them. Compliance, submission, and timid fear—though inferior to others, these three ensure success. Those who extend knowledge outward exhaust their spirit in pursuit of externals. Bold action breeds resentment, and vigorous advance invites enmity. Benevolence and righteousness invite reproach.

Those who grasp the essence of life appear vast; those who grasp knowledge appear small. Those who grasp the great mandate follow; those who grasp the petty mandate endure. A man who had an audience with the King of Song was granted ten chariots, with which he grew arrogant toward Zhuangzi. Zhuangzi said: "By the river lived a poor family who made their living weaving mats from reeds. Their son dived into the depths and retrieved a pearl worth a thousand gold pieces. His father said to him, 'Bring a stone and smash it! For such a pearl must lie in the ninefold abyss beneath the chin of a black dragon. That you obtained it means you encountered the dragon while it slept. Had the black dragon awakened, what scrap of you would remain?' Now, the depths of the state of Song are far greater than the ninefold abyss, and the ferocity of the King of Song far exceeds that of the black dragon."
