"Had you obtained those chariots, you must have encountered the King while he slept. Were the King of Song to awaken, you would be reduced to dust!"

Later, an emissary was sent to Zhuangzi, who replied to the messenger: "Have you seen the sacrificial ox? As the *Cheng* commentary explains, 'Sacrificial oxen are reared for three months prior to being offered in the ancestral temple.' Clad in embroidered robes and fed with fine beans and grasses—yet when led into the great temple, though it might wish to remain a lone calf, could it do so?"

As Zhuangzi neared death, his disciples wished to give him a lavish burial. Zhuangzi said: "I take heaven and earth as my inner and outer coffins, the sun and moon as my paired jades, the stars and constellations as my pearls and beads, and the ten thousand things as my funeral gifts. Are my burial provisions not already complete? What could be added to them?"

His disciples said: "We fear the crows and kites will devour you."

Zhuangzi replied: "Above, I would be eaten by crows and kites; below, by ants and mole crickets. To take from one to give to the other—how partial is that!"

To level the uneven with what is not level—such leveling is itself uneven.

To level the world with partial views—such leveling remains uneven. To govern with what is not governing—such governing is no governance. Guo [Xiang] says: "征 means 'response.'" Cheng [Xuanying] says: "The sage is without intent; when moved, he responds—this is true response. If one responds to things with intent, one cannot truly respond." The enlightened are but servants to things. Cheng says: "Those who flaunt their enlightenment to attend to affairs are driven by things—how can they master others?" The spirit responds to them. Xuan [Ying] says: "Those who follow the spirit’s principle respond without fail wherever they go." That enlightenment cannot surpass spirit has long been so, yet fools rely on what they see and employ their own cunning. They become entangled in others—Xuan says: "Drowned in human affairs"—their merit lies in externals, all their effort bent toward the outer. Is this not lamentable?

**Miscellaneous Chapters, "The World" (Chapter Thirty-Three)**

Those who order the world with methods are many—Cheng says: "方 means 'Way.'" All claim their practices cannot be surpassed—Xuan says: "其有 refers to what they have learned." But where does the ancient Way truly reside? It is said: "Nowhere it is not present."

“”It is asked: “From where does spirit descend? From where does enlightenment emerge?” If it is nowhere not present, then how do spirit-sages and enlightened kings descend and emerge, uniquely distinct from the multitude? Xuan says: “This is another question posed.” “Sages arise from somewhere, kings achieve from somewhere—all originate from the One.” The text below speaks of “the Way of inner sageliness and outer kingship.” Xuan says: “This is the answer.”

Not departing from the source is called the celestial person. “Not departing” is like Confucius’ saying that Yan [Hui] did not violate the ancestral master—this refers to the natural. Not departing from essence is called the spirit person. Cheng says: “Unmixed purity is called spiritlike subtlety.” Not departing from the real is called the perfected person. Cheng says: “Unwavering and uncontrived is called the ultimate.”

Taking Heaven as their source, virtue as their root, and the Dao as their gate, they foreshadow transformation—transformation beyond reckoning, appearing in things as their inception. These are called sages. Cheng says: “The four types above are but one; their names vary according to their functions.”

Taking benevolence as kindness, righteousness as principle, ritual as conduct, and music as harmony, they are warmly compassionate and humane—these are called exemplary persons. Xuan says: “Exemplary persons are the remnants of the Way.”

Taking law as distinction, names as standards—Xuan says: “Taking legal measures as distinctions, titles and designations as standards”—taking verification as proof—*Shiwen* reads: “Verification, originally written as ‘conduct’”—Xuan says: “Taking the documents they handle as evidence”—taking investigation as decision—Xuan says: “Taking examination of what they handle to decide matters”—their reckoning is one, two, three, four, and so on. Xuan says: “So clear and unerring are they.”

Officials rank one another by these—Xuan says: “This is yet another class of people. ‘Ranking’ means ordering by these. Official posts are the traces of names and laws.” Taking affairs as the constant—affairs refer to daily tasks—taking clothing and food as their chief concern, they multiply and store, caring for the old, weak, orphaned, and widowed, all provided for. “Multiplying” refers to production; “storing” refers to goods and wealth. Their care extends even to those without recourse. This is the governance of the people.

Xuan says: “Yet another class of people.”

The ancients—were they not complete! They matched the divine luminaries, harmonized with Heaven and Earth, nurtured the myriad things, brought harmony to the world, and extended their beneficence to the hundred clans. They were enlightened in the fundamental principles and bound to the derivative measures—Guo says: “When the fundamental principles are clear, the derivatives do not stray.” Their understanding pervaded the six directions and opened the four quarters. Whether in the subtle or the coarse, the great or the small, their operation was present everywhere without exception.

Those whose clarity lay in the numerical measures were preserved in great number by the historians of old laws and transmitted traditions. Xuan says: “This refers to how the historians transmitted them.” Those found in the *Odes*, *Documents*, *Rites*, and *Music* were widely elucidated by the scholars of Zou and Lu and the girdle-wearing masters. The *shi* were Confucians; the *jinshen xiansheng* were those who held office. Cheng says: “*Jin* means tablet, or to insert; *shen* means sash.” Xuan says: “This refers to how the Six Classics were transmitted.” The *Odes* guide the will, the *Documents* guide affairs, the *Rites* guide conduct, the *Music* guides harmony, the *Changes* guides the yin and yang, and the *Spring and Autumn* guides names and duties.

Their principles, dispersed throughout the world and established in the Central States—*she* means to apply—were at times praised and expounded by the various schools. Xuan says: “This refers to how the Hundred Schools transmitted them.”

When the world fell into great disorder, the worthy and sagely withdrew their light and concealed their traces. Virtue and the Dao were no longer unified, and the world grasped at partial insights to take pride in. Each partial insight was like the clarity of the ear, eye, nose, or mouth, each possessing its own illumination but unable to communicate with the others. So too were the various arts of the Hundred Schools, each possessing its own excellence and occasional utility. Yet they were neither comprehensive nor all-encompassing, being but partial scholars.

They divided the beauty of Heaven and Earth, dissected the principles of the myriad things, and examined the wholeness of the ancients. Few could fully embody the beauty of Heaven and Earth or match the countenance of the divine luminaries. Thus, the Way of inner sageliness and outer kingship grew dim and unclear, repressed and unexpressed. The people of the world each followed their own desires to create their own methods.

How lamentable! The Hundred Schools departed and did not return, and their paths must diverge. Later scholars, unfortunate in not witnessing the purity of Heaven and Earth or the great essence of the ancients, will see the Dao and its arts torn asunder by the world.

They did not indulge in extravagance for later generations, nor waste themselves on the myriad things, nor seek brilliance in numerical measures. Xuan says: “They did not display luxury, did not engage in wastefulness, did not pursue ostentation.” They used the inked cord to correct themselves. Cheng says: “*Jiao* means to refine. They used benevolence and righteousness as the inked cord to refine their conduct.” Thus, they prepared for the world’s emergencies. Guo says: “Diligence and frugality leave wealth in surplus, so emergencies are provided for.” Among the ancient arts of the Dao, some were like this.

Mo Di and Qin Guli heard of this approach and delighted in it. Mo Di, a grandee of Song, advocated simplicity and frugality. Qin Guli, a disciple of Mo Di, rejected the music of the Five Emperors and Three Kings,嫌其奢侈. They carried it to excess, merely following their own inclinations. Their excesses were but their own extreme compliance, insufficient to teach the world. They created *Fei Le* and called it *Jie Yong*. In life, they did not sing; in death, they wore no mourning. Cheng says: “*Fei Le* and *Jie Yong* are two chapters in the writings of Mozi. Not singing in life is why they rejected music; wearing no mourning in death is why they advocated frugality.” By this, they meant no funerary garments such as shrouds, coffins, or outer coffins.

Mozi advocated universal love and mutual benefit while opposing strife. Guo says: “When the hundred clans are all diligent and frugal, each with surplus, they take strife to be wrong.” His Way did not involve anger. Cheng says: “They mastered themselves, so they did not resent or rage at things.” They also loved learning broadly but were not tolerant of differences. Guo says: “Having already taken themselves as correct, they wished all things to be the same as themselves.” They did not align with the former kings, nor did they approve of them. They disparaged the rites and music of antiquity. Guo says: “They嫌其奢靡.” The Yellow Emperor had *Xian Chi*, Yao had *Da Zhang*, Shun had *Da Shao*, Yu had *Da Xia*, Tang had *Da Huo*, King Wen had the music of the *Bi Yong*, and King Wu and the Duke of Zhou created *Wu*.

In the mourning rites of old, the noble and base had their proper forms, the high and low their proper ranks. The Son of Heaven had seven layers of inner and outer coffins, the feudal lords five, the grand officers three, and the officers two. Yet Mozi alone, in life, did not sing, and in death, wore no mourning. He used a paulownia coffin three inches thick without an outer coffin as his model. Teaching others thus, he surely did not love them; practicing it himself, he certainly did not love himself.

Guo Xiang says: “This both violates human nature and places oneself in meagerness.” Yet Mozi’s Way has not failed—indeed, Mozi’s Way has not yet failed. Nevertheless, to sing yet not truly sing, to weep yet not truly weep, to rejoice yet not truly rejoice—is this truly akin to human nature? Is this truly in accord with human sentiment? In life, they toil; in death, they are meager. Their Way is excessively harsh. Guo Songtao says: “The *Shuowen* glosses *hu* as ‘exhausted.’ The *Guanzi*’s ‘Di Yuan’ chapter reads: ‘The next is called the Five *Hu*.’ *Hu* means meager.” It causes people sorrow, it causes people grief. Its practice is difficult to sustain. I fear it cannot be the Way of the sage, for it turns against the hearts of the world, and the world cannot endure it. Though Mozi alone could bear it and practice it himself, what of the world? Separated from the world, he is far from the kingly Way. Guo Xiang says: “This is not the Way of the king.”

Mozi spoke of the Way, declaring its origins: “In ancient times, Yu dammed the floodwaters, dredged the Yangtze and Yellow Rivers, and opened the roads to the four barbarians and the Nine Provinces. Three hundred famous rivers—Yu Yue says: ‘*Mountains* should be *rivers*, a scribal error. This passage speaks solely of rivers and should not mention tributaries while omitting the great rivers. The *Lüshi Chunqiu*’s ‘Shi Lan’ chapter and the *Huainanzi*’s ‘Di Xing Xun’ both say *six hundred* famous rivers.’—three thousand tributaries, and countless smaller streams. Yu personally wielded the basket and plowshare, channeling the waters of all the world’s rivers together.” Lu Deming’s *Exegesis* says: “*Gao* was formerly read as *gu kao*; Cui and Guo read it as *tuo*, so it should be written as *tuo*. Sima Biao says: ‘A vessel for carrying earth.’ *Si* is read as *si*; the *San Cang* says: ‘The iron tip of a plow.’ Cui says: ‘A mallet.’ Sima Biao says: ‘A vessel for holding water.’ *Nine* is also written as *jiu*, meaning ‘to gather.’” Guo Songtao says: “To merge the waters of all rivers into the great rivers is called *jiu za*.” His calves bore no flesh, his shins no hair. He bathed in heavy rains, combed his hair in fierce winds, and established the ten thousand states. Having thus settled the ten thousand states, Yu—a great sage—yet wearied his body for the world in this way.”

The later Mohists mostly wore coarse furs and hempen cloth, bound their feet with straw sandals—Cheng Xuanying says: “The later Mohists were Mozi’s disciples. *Qiu he* means rough clothing; wooden sandals are called *qi*, straw sandals *qiao*.”—and toiled day and night without rest, taking self-affliction as their utmost principle. They said: “If one cannot do this, it is not Yu’s Way and is unworthy of being called Mohist.” Thus Mozi admonished his followers.

Xiangli Qin’s disciples, the followers of Wu Hou—Cheng Xuanying says: “Xiangli was his surname, Qin his given name, a Mohist master of the southern regions. Wu Hou were fellow Mohist scholars.”—and the likes of Han Fei’s *Xian Xue* chapter: “There were the Mohists of the Xiangli clan, the Xiangfu clan, and the Xiangling clan.”—Ku Huo, Ji Chi, and Deng Lingzi all recited the Mohist canon—Li Yi says: “Ku Huo and Ji Chi were the names of two individuals.” Note: *Deng Ling* may be *Xiang Ling*, a corruption due to similar graphs—but their interpretations diverged, each calling the other “deviant Mohists.” *Bei jue* means perverse and cunning, each claiming to be a separate Mohist faction. They debated *jian bai* and *tong yi*, Guo Xiang says: “Refuting each other’s arguments,” and responded to one another with *ji ou* and *bu wu* propositions—Guo Xiang says: “Affirming one side.” *Ji* is the same as *qi*. Lu Deming’s *Exegesis* says: “*Wu* means ‘same.’” Note: Odd and even are inherently different, yet forcing them to correspond makes anything possible to equate. They revered the *juzi* as sages—Guo Xiang says: “The *juzi* were the Mohists’ foremost disciples.” Lu Deming’s *Exegesis* says: “Like the Confucians’ great scholars.”—all wishing to be their successors—Cheng Xuanying says: “To serve as their leaders.”—hoping to become their heirs—Guo Xiang says: “Aspiring to continue their lineage.”—and to this day, their disputes remain unresolved—Guo Xiang says: “Their teachings have not ceased.”

Mozi and Qin Guli’s intentions were correct, but their conduct was not—Cheng Xuanying says: “Their aim was to save the world, which is why they were right; but they went too far, which is why they were wrong.” They would compel later Mohists to wear themselves out with calves devoid of flesh and shins devoid of hair, competing with one another in this. Such conduct is the height of disorder and the lowest form of governance—Guo Xiang says: “Their crime in disordering the world is great, while their merit in governing it is small.” Nevertheless, Mozi was truly one who loved the world, truly one who could love others—Yu Yue says: “This echoes Mencius’ idea of ‘Mozi’s universal love.’” If one sought such a person, one would not find him; if one sought a way to save the world, would one not find it? The words *ye* and *ye* were interchangeable in ancient times—Yu Yue says: “This echoes the idea of ‘sincerely seeking it.’” Even if it meant withering away, he would not abandon it—even if his body withered, he could not bear to let go—Yu Yue says: “This echoes Mencius’ idea of ‘grinding his head and heels smooth for it.’” What a man of consummate talent! One might call him a man who exhausted his talents. Unburdened by custom, unshackled by things. Unadorned by externals, not artificially refined. Uncompromising with others, never careless. Unresentful of the crowd, harboring no malice. He desired the world’s peace and stability to preserve the lives of the people, placing the world’s living beings above all. Once the sustenance of self and others was fully met, he stopped, not seeking excess. In this, he bared his heart—Guo Xiang says: “Revealing his unadulterated intent.”—and in this lay the ancient arts of the Way.

Song Jian and Yin Wen heard of his teachings and delighted in them—Cheng Xuanying says: “Song and Yin were contemporaries of King Xuan of Qi, both studying at the Jixia Academy.”

(Note: The *Hanshu*’s *Yiwenzhi* lists them under the School of Names.) Song Jian authored one treatise, Yin Wen two, both taking the common people as their teachers and deriving their names therefrom. Their nature aligned with their teachings, so upon hearing of his Way, they delighted in and cherished it.

They wore the cap of Mount Hua as a personal emblem—Guo [Xiang] says: “Mount Hua is level above and below.”—and began their engagement with the myriad things by dispelling obstructions. *Shiwen* [notes]: “‘Began’ means ‘to take as the first step.’ Cui [Zhuan] says: ‘To distinguish good and evil, while overlooking what lies beyond.’” They spoke of the heart’s capacity, naming it “the heart’s conduct”—Cheng [Xuanying] says: “‘Naming’ here means ‘to designate.’ Every utterance and word should allow the heart to embrace the myriad things; this capacity to receive is called the heart’s conduct.” (Note: This means that as my heart is, so too is the conduct I extend to others.)

They sought harmony to foster joy—*Shiwen*: “‘Harmony’: Cui [Zhuan] reads it as *er*, Guo [Xiang] as *er*. Sima [Biao] says: ‘A look of deep sincerity.’ Cui, Guo, and Wang [Bi] say: ‘Harmony.’ To harmonize the myriad things is to bring them together in joy.”—and to bring order to the world, compelling others to be governed by their Way. They urged rulers to adopt it as their guiding principle, entreating all contemporary lords to make this heart their foundation.

They endured insult without shame, refusing to call it humiliation. They rescued the people from strife, forbidding aggression and halting warfare to end the world’s battles—*Shiwen*: “‘Halting’ means ‘to cease.’” With this, they traversed the world, persuading the high and instructing the low. Though the world did not accept their teachings, they did not abandon them, persisting tirelessly. Thus it is said: “Though rejected by high and low, they pressed on insistently.” The “high” refers to contemporary rulers; the “low,” to their ministers.

Yet for all this, they did too much for others and too little for themselves, for their words were as follows: “We ask only that five *sheng* of rice be set aside as sufficient. Though the master may fear it will not fill him, and though we disciples may go hungry, we do not forget the world.”—Cheng [Xuanying] says: “Song and Yin called the common people ‘masters’ and referred to themselves as ‘disciples,’ placing others before themselves.” (Note: This refers to Song and Yin’s request for provisions, saying they asked only that five *sheng* of rice be set aside for the master.) Day and night they did not rest, declaring: “We must surely save them!” How lofty their ambition as saviors of the world!

Xuan [Yuan] says: “Again, they said, ‘We must surely find a way to sustain ourselves!’—plotting to preserve the lives of the people, they were haughty as saviors of the world.”

It is said: “The noble person does not engage in petty scrutiny, nor does he lend himself to things.” To deem something of no benefit to the world is to declare that illuminating it is worse than abandoning it. Again, it is said: “The noble person ought not be overly exacting, thus he disregards contempt and disdain, refusing to borrow external things for his own sake.” Therefore, he does not calculate hunger or satiety, while others all flaunt their own discernment. Yet if one calculates and compares too much, though it may benefit the world, none will act upon it. Thus, Song and Yin held that those who offer no benefit to the world are better off not illuminating things at all.

They took the prohibition of attack and the cessation of arms as external matters—Xuan says: “Externally, they saved the world through this.”—and the restraint of desires and their moderation as internal matters—Xuan says: “Internally, they mastered themselves through this.” In their great and small, refined and coarse aspects, their conduct extended no further than this. If their conduct stopped here, then the greatness or smallness, refinement or coarseness of their Way and arts also went no further.

Impartial yet not partisan—Cui’s edition reads “partisan” (*dang*), with the note: “Utterly impartial, without partisanship.” Lu says: “‘Not partisan’ (*bu dang*) is correct.”—easygoing and without self-interest—Cheng says: “Level and easy.”—decisive yet without fixed bias—Xuan says: “Cutting away entanglements, without partiality.”—they followed things without duality—Xuan says: “Following things as they go, without divided intent.”—they did not dwell on deliberation, nor scheme with knowledge, without sidelong glances or cunning plots. Toward things, they made no distinctions, but went along with them. Among the ancient arts of the Dao, some lay precisely here.

Peng Meng, Tian Pian, and Shen Dao heard of this approach and delighted in it—Cheng says: “All were recluses of Qi, who gathered at Jixia and each composed several chapters of writings.” Yu says: “According to the text below, Peng Meng was likely Tian Pian’s teacher. The *Yi Lin* quotes the *Yin Wenzi* as recording Peng Meng’s words: ‘Pheasants and hares in the wild are all pursued because their apportionment is not yet settled; chickens and pigs fill the market, yet none covet them, for their apportionment is fixed.’”

They took the equalization of the ten thousand things as their foremost principle—Xuan says: “They made this their primary concern.”—and declared: “Heaven can cover them but cannot bear them; earth can bear them but cannot cover them; the great Dao can encompass them but cannot distinguish them.” Knowing that the ten thousand things each have what they can and cannot do, they said: “Selection does not reach all, for there must be some left unselected. Teaching does not extend fully, for there must be some who do not receive it. Only the Dao leaves nothing out.” Only the Dao encompasses all—this is what is meant by equalization.

Thus, Shen Dao—Yu says: “The *Records of the Grand Historian*’s ‘Biographies of Meng and Xun’ state: ‘Shen Dao was a man of Zhao who composed twelve discourses.’ The *Han History*’s ‘Treatise on Arts and Letters’ lists among the Legalists ‘*Shenzi* in forty-two chapters. His name was Dao, and he preceded Shen Buhai and Han Fei, who cited him.’”—abandoned knowledge and discarded the self—Cheng says: “Stilled thoughts, abandoned knowledge, forgot the body, and discarded the self.”—and followed what could not be avoided, sifting through things to establish his principles—*Shiwen* says: “*Lingtai* means the same as sifting (*shatai*). *Ling* is pronounced *ling*.”—with the note: “Though Shen Dao abandoned knowledge and discarded the self, he still relied on what could not be avoided, sifting through people and things to uphold this as his principle.”

He said: “To know without knowing—this is to come close to knowledge and then nearly harm it.” His words were: “In all matters of knowing others, one should act as if one does not know. To come close to having some knowledge is already to approach harming it.” This was Shen Dao’s abandonment of knowledge—Cheng says: “‘Near’ (*lin*) means close.”

Crooked and unsteady, he mocked the world’s esteem for worthies—*Shiwen* says: “*Xike* means the appearance of being askew and unsteady.”—with the note: “Though his use of people was crooked and unsteady, without anyone to employ, he laughed at the world’s reverence for worthies.” Unrestrained and without conduct, he condemned the world’s great sages. In himself, he was dissolute and careless, without conduct to commend, yet he dismissed the world’s great sages, lowering them with no lofty discourse.

Pounding and patting, rounding and severing, he turned with things—Guo says: “Still like pounding and patting, he had not yet merged completely.”—*Shiwen* says: “*Wan* means round.”—with the note: Guo’s explanation of *pounding and patting* refers to striking like a mallet. If things were not yet in harmony, he would pound and pat them heavily, leaving none unaligned. This is the meaning of *pounding and patting*—forcing what does not align to align. *Rounding and severing* means that though things were cut, they remained perfectly round, with no trace of rupture, all in accord with the idea of turning with things. This was Shen Dao’s discarding of the self.

Abandoning right and wrong, he did whatever could avoid harm—Xuan says: “Not clinging to right and wrong, he hoped to avoid entanglement.”—he did not model himself on knowledge or deliberation, nor did he consider before or after—*Shiwen* says: “The first *zhi* is pronounced as *intelligence*.”—with the note: “He did not model himself on others’ knowledge or deliberation, nor did he inquire into the before or after of affairs.” He stood towering and alone. Impartial and easygoing, he could thus stand lofty and firm.

Pushed, he then moved; pulled, he then went—like the returning of a whirlwind—Xuan says: “Revolving without direction.”—like the spinning of a feather—Xuan says: “A feather falling from the sky, spinning without end.”—like the groove of a grindstone—The groove of a grindstone is smooth, an analogy for its flawless motion. Whole and without fault, he could thus preserve himself without incurring blame. In movement and stillness, he committed no transgressions. Still without transgression, in motion also without transgression—how could blame arise?

Why was this so?—A hypothetical question, asking why he could be thus.—For things without knowledge, there is no trouble of establishing the self, no burden of employing knowledge. In movement and stillness, they do not depart from principle, and thus they pass their lives without praise. Things without knowledge—such as wood and stone.

Speaking in analogy to those things without knowledge: they do not establish the self as a standard, and thus avoid the trouble of being pointed at or singled out; they do not employ wisdom to speculate upon one another, and thus escape the burden of envy and jealousy. Moved, they are in motion; set down, they are at rest—ever unseparated from the principle of things, plain and easily seen. Therefore, throughout their lives, none praise them. Without praise, there is also no blame.

Thus it is said: “It comes down to being like things without knowledge.” This is to say, it is carried to its utmost. “There is no need for worthies and sages—for even a clod of earth does not lose the Dao.” Why employ worthies and sages? Even a lump of soil does not lose the Way.

The eminent and powerful mocked them, saying: “The Way of Shen Dao is not the conduct of the living but the principle of the dead—it is nothing but bizarre.” Those among the eminent and powerful who could engage with his teachings would laugh together, saying: “Shenzi’s Way is not the conduct of the living but rather the principle of the dead—it is only enough to elicit the world’s astonishment.” Tian Pian was the same; his words were identical, and Shen Dao is mentioned to include Tian Pian.

He studied under Peng Meng and received the teaching of “no-teaching.” The teaching of no-teaching: one observes his conduct and learns from it, so that the heart attains understanding on its own. To mention Meng’s disciple along with his teacher is to make Peng Meng’s position clear.

Peng Meng’s teacher said: “The Daoists of antiquity reached the point where none affirmed them and none denied them.” This is no different from Shen Dao’s saying “it comes down to being like things without knowledge.” “Their influence was swift as the wind—how could it be put into words?” Xiang and Guo said: “窢 is the sound of wind blowing against something.” The influence and teaching of the ancient Daoists passed swiftly—how could they be transmitted in words?

They constantly opposed others, remaining unseen and unobserved, always countering the opinions and discussions of others, never appearing admirable in their eyes. The text below says: “Taking opposition to others as their substance.” Yet they could not avoid making judgments. Even when compelled to decide, they could only adapt to things, and thus could not escape Shen Dao’s cautious and rigid judgments.

Their so-called Dao was not the Dao, and what they spoke as right was not without error. Guo said: “韪 means ‘right.’” Note: This refers to the words of Peng Meng’s teacher—what was right in them still contained error, and the Dao remained unseen.

Peng Meng, Tian Pian, and Shen Dao did not know the Dao. Thus, these three can simply be said not to have known the Dao. Nevertheless, in broad terms, they had once heard of it. Yet considering their general drift, they had once received old teachings. For instance, “abandoning knowledge and relinquishing the self” must have come from some teacher—only their theories drifted ever further from it.

Impartial yet not partisan—Cui’s edition has “黨,” explaining: “Utterly impartial, without faction.” Lu said: “‘不黨’ is correct.” Easygoing and without self-interest, Cheng said: “Level and easy.” Decisive and without attachment, Xuan said: “Cutting off entanglements, without partiality.” Following things without duality, Xuan said: “Following things as they go, without divided intent.” Unconcerned with deliberation, unplanned by knowledge—without sidelong glances, without clever schemes. Making no distinctions among things, going along with them—this was where the Daoist arts of antiquity lay.

Peng Meng, Tian Pian, and Shen Dao heard of this influence and delighted in it. Cheng said: “All were recluses of Qi, gathering at Jixia, each composing several chapters.” Yu said: “According to the text below, Peng Meng was likely Tian Pian’s teacher. The *Yi Lin* quotes the *Yin Wenzi* as having Peng Meng say: ‘When pheasants and hares are in the wild, everyone chases them because their share is not yet fixed; when chickens and pigs fill the market, no one desires them because their share is already fixed.’”

They took the equalization of the ten thousand things as their foremost principle. Xuan said: “They took this as their primary matter.” They said: “Heaven can cover them but cannot bear them; earth can bear them but cannot cover them; the great Dao can encompass them but cannot distinguish them.” Knowing that the ten thousand things each have what they can and cannot do, they said: “Selection is not all-encompassing—there must be those left unselected. Teaching does not reach all—there must be those who do not receive it. Only the Dao leaves nothing out.” Only the Dao encompasses all—this is what is meant by equalization.

Thus Shen Dao—Yu said: “The *Records of the Grand Historian*’s ‘Biographies of Mencius and Xunzi’ state: ‘Shen Dao was from Zhao and composed twelve essays.’ The *Han History*’s ‘Treatise on Arts and Letters’ lists among the Legalists ‘forty-two chapters of the *Shenzi*. His name was Dao, and he preceded Shen Buhai and Han Fei, who cited him.’”—abandoned knowledge and relinquished the self. Cheng said: “Ceasing deliberation, abandoning knowledge, forgetting the body, relinquishing the self.” Yet he followed what could not be avoided, sifting through things to establish his principle. The *Shiwen* says: “泠汰 is like sifting.” Note: Though Shen Dao abandoned knowledge and relinquished the self, he still acted when compelled, sifting through things to establish his principle.

He said: “Knowing the unknowable—this is to approach knowledge thinly and then draw near to harm.” His words said: “In the Dao of knowing others, one should act as if one does not know. To approach knowledge even thinly is already to draw near to harm.” This was Shen Dao’s abandonment of knowledge. Cheng said: “邻 means ‘near.’”

Crooked and unsteady, bearing no responsibility, he laughed at the world’s esteem for worthies. Though in himself unrestrained and dissolute, without conduct to commend, he denied the world’s great sages, belittling them without lofty discourse. With blunt and rigid judgments, adapting to things, Guo said: “Still retaining bluntness, not yet perfectly merged.”

The *Shiwen* annotates: “輐 means ‘round.’” The case explains: Guo’s gloss of *zhuīpāi* (椎拍) refers to striking like a mallet. When any thing is not yet fully aligned, one strikes it heavily with a mallet, and it will align without fail. This is the meaning of *zhuīpāi*—to force the unaligned into alignment. *Yuànduàn* (輐断) means that though a decision is made, it is exceedingly round, leaving no trace of rupture, all in accord with the intent to adapt to things. This was Shen Dao’s relinquishing of the self.

Abandoning right and wrong, he acted merely to avoid harm. Xuan comments: “Not clinging to right and wrong, he sought to be free of entanglement.” He did not model himself on wisdom or foresight, nor did he consider before and after. The *Shiwen* notes: “The first *zhi* is read as ‘wisdom.’” The case explains: He did not follow others’ wisdom or foresight, nor inquire into the sequence of events. He remained towering and unmoved. Vast and impartial, he could stand loftily. He moved only when pushed, advanced only when dragged—like a whirling wind, Xuan comments: “Turning without fixed direction”; like a feather’s spiral, Xuan comments: “A feather falling from the sky, spinning without cease”; like the groove of a millstone, its path worn smooth by grinding. Whole and without fault, he could preserve himself without incurring blame. In movement and stillness, he committed no transgression, and thus never incurred guilt. How was this so? A rhetorical question: How could he achieve this? For a thing without knowledge—free from the peril of establishing the self, free from the burden of employing knowledge—its movement and stillness never deviate from principle, and thus it remains without praise throughout its life. A thing without knowledge, like wood or stone, does not establish the self as a standard, so it avoids the peril of being singled out; it does not use wisdom to speculate, so it escapes the burden of envy. Moved, it moves; set down, it is still—always in accord with the principle of things, plain and evident. Thus, no one praises it throughout its life, and without praise, it also avoids fault. Hence it is said: “To be like a thing without knowledge—this is the utmost of Shen Dao’s teaching. There is no need for worthies and sages, for even a clod of earth does not lose the Dao.” Why employ worthies and sages? Even a lump of soil does not stray from the Dao.

The proud and eminent mocked him, saying: “Shen Dao’s Way is not the conduct of the living but the principle of the dead—it merely invites strangeness.” The bold and distinguished among his contemporaries laughed together, saying: “Shen Dao’s Way is not the conduct of the living but leads to the principle of the dead—it only earns the world’s bewilderment.” Tian Pian was the same; his words were identical, so Shen Dao is cited to encompass him as well.

He studied under Peng Meng and attained the teaching of non-teaching. The teaching of non-teaching: observing his conduct, one learned and grasped it inwardly. Mentioning Meng’s disciple and teacher, Meng’s own views may be inferred. Peng Meng’s teacher said: “The Daoists of antiquity reached the point of neither affirming nor negating.” This is no different from Shen Dao’s words, “to be like a thing without knowledge.” Their influence was swift and fleeting—how could it be put into words? Xiang and Guo comment: “窢 is the sound of wind blowing against.” The teaching of the ancient Daoists passed swiftly, like a gust—how could it be transmitted in words? They constantly opposed others, remaining unseen, constantly countering others’ opinions without being observed or admired. As stated below: “Taking opposition to others as their substance.” Yet they could not avoid *yuànduàn* (輐断). Even when compelled to make decisions, they still adapted to things, unable to escape Shen Dao’s *yuànduàn*. *Yuàn* (輐) and *●* share the same sound and meaning.

What they called the Dao was not the Dao, and what they spoke as right was not free from wrong. Guo comments: “韪 means ‘right.’” The case explains: Peng Meng’s teacher’s words contained rightness within wrongness, for they did not truly grasp the Dao. Peng Meng, Tian Pian, and Shen Dao did not know the Dao. Thus, these three may simply be said not to have known the Dao. Nevertheless, in broad strokes, they had once heard of it. Yet though they had once received some instruction—for instance, in “abandoning knowledge and relinquishing the self”—their theories strayed ever further from its thread.

They took the root as refined and things as coarse. Cheng comments: “The root is nonbeing; things are being. Using nonbeing as the wondrous Dao is refined; using being as affairs and objects is coarse.” They regarded accumulation as insufficient. Guo comments: “Entrusting all to the world, they had more than enough.” Serene and alone, they dwelled with spirit and illumination. Xuan comments: “This is the teaching of emptiness and nonaction.” Among the Daoist arts of antiquity, there were those who practiced this. Guan Yin and Lao Dan heard of their influence and delighted in it.

The *Shiwen* annotates: “Guan Yin was Guan Ling Yin Xi, also said to be Yin Xi, styled Gongdu. Lao Dan was Laozi, who wrote nineteen chapters for Yin Xi.” Cheng comments: “During the reign of King Ping of Zhou, he was the magistrate of Hangu Pass, hence called Guan Yin.” Yu comments: “The *Han History*’s *Treatise on the Arts* lists *Guan Yinzi* in nine chapters, with a note: ‘Named Xi, he was an official at the pass.’ Some take Yin Xi as a compound name, which is incorrect. The *Han History* does not record a nineteen-chapter work by Laozi. The *Annals of Lü Buwei*, in the chapter ‘Without Duality,’ states: ‘Guan Yin valued purity,’ with Gao’s commentary: ‘Guan Yin was the magistrate of the pass, named Xi. Skilled in divining winds and omens, he knew a divine man would arrive, and when Laozi came, Xi delighted in him and requested the writing of the *Shangzhi Jing* in five thousand words.’ The title *Shangzhi Jing* is not found elsewhere.”

They established their teaching on constant nonbeing and nonhaving, and took the Great Unity as their foundation. Cheng comments: “They established their doctrine on the constancy of nonbeing, and grasped its essence in the emptiness of the Great Unity.”

Embodying gentleness, humility, and deference as their outward form, and embracing emptiness and non-destruction of the myriad things as their inner reality. Cheng comments: “Outward form refers to external conduct. They took softness, weakness, humility, and harmony as the external manifestations of their wisdom and skill, while regarding empty kindness and all-encompassing clarity as the inner virtue of their true wisdom.”

Guan Yin said: “Abiding nowhere in oneself, the forms of things naturally manifest.” Xuan comments: “With no private agenda of one’s own, one moves in harmony with things as they appear.” Their movement was like water, their stillness like a mirror, their response like an echo. Xuan comments: “All arise from having no fixed intent.” Fading away like something vanished, serene like purity itself, those who harmonized with them found accord, while those who sought to possess them lost them. Xuan comments: “Harmony comes from merging with things; loss comes from self-assertion.” Never taking the lead, they constantly followed others. Cheng comments: “Harmonizing without initiating.”

Laozi said: “Knowing the masculine, yet guarding the feminine,” Xuan comments: “Abiding in what seems incapable while possessing ability.” “becoming the world’s valley stream.” Xuan comments: “Dwelling in lowliness to receive all, possessing without accumulation.” “Knowing the white, yet guarding the disgraced,” “pure yet not self-righteous in purity,” “becoming the world’s valley.” Xuan comments: “Abiding in emptiness to receive all, responding without hoarding.”

While others all sought to be first, they alone took the rear, saying: “I bear the world’s filth.” While others all sought fullness, they alone embraced emptiness, possessing nothing yet having abundance, standing alone in self-sufficiency with abundance. Guo comments: “This is what it means to be independent and self-sufficient.” Xuan comments: “This is an emphatic reiteration.” In conducting themselves, they moved slowly yet expended nothing. Xuan comments: “Not taking the lead, they moved slowly. Not leading meant fewer affairs; fewer affairs meant no waste.” They practiced non-action and yet mocked cleverness. Non-action appeared clumsy, yet it could deride those who relied on skill.

While others all sought fortune, they alone bent to preserve wholeness, saying: “Merely avoiding blame.” While others ceaselessly pursued fortune, they alone yielded to safeguard their integrity, saying: “Simply to evade misfortune.” Taking depth as their root and restraint as their guiding principle, Cheng comments: “Taking profound mystery as the root of their virtue, and frugality as the guiding thread of their conduct.” They declared: “What is hard will break; what is sharp will blunt.” Always tolerant toward things, never cutting others down, Cheng comments: “Content with their lot and adhering to their place, they never encroached upon others.” This may be called the ultimate attainment. (The original text reads “though not yet,” stating: “Changed following Li’s edition.”)

Ah, Guan Yin and Laozi! Truly the vast and authentic perfected ones of antiquity!

Vast and formless, ever-changing and constant—death and life alike! Heaven and earth together with me! As the "Qi Wu Lun" states: "Heaven and earth are born together with me." The numinous and bright go forth—where do they wander in their boundless haze? Where do they drift in their sudden swiftness? The numinous and bright depart, yet none know their destination. All things are wholly encompassed—Xuan comments: "Nothing is left unembraced." Yet none can serve as their refuge, for there is no place to return.

Among the Daoist arts of antiquity, some resided in this. Zhuang Zhou heard of their influence and delighted in it. With discourses remote and drifting—Shiwen notes: "As if forgetting all concrete reality"—and words vast and empty, boundless and without edge, he spoke without traceable beginning or visible limit. At times, he gave free rein to his thoughts without constraint—Shiwen reads "and sudden," omitting "not," which is closer to the mark. He meant that his words arrived in a flash, not displaying themselves through partiality. Xuan comments: "He did not present himself through one-sided views." Cheng comments: "Partiality means lack of balance."

Taking the world as turbid and murky, he deemed it unfit for solemn discourse. Solemn discourse refers to straightforward argument. Instead, he used goblet words to flow and extend, weighty words as truth, and parables to broaden meaning. Since the world could not bear solemn discourse, he employed these three modes of speech, as previously seen in the "Yu Yan" chapter. "Flowing and extending" means to follow the principles of things and develop them further, as in "goblet words arise daily, thus flowing and extending." Weighty words recount the sayings of the venerable, making listeners accept them as true—hence they "serve to complete speech." Parables expand the listener’s understanding, as in "borrowing external views to discuss them."

Alone, he communed with the spirit of heaven and earth, his own spirit moving in concert with the cosmos, dwelling in the loftiest realm. Yao comments: "Zhuangzi regarded Guan Yin and Laozi as no more than the perfected ones described at the chapter’s outset as 'not departing from the real'—still not the ultimate. But Zhuang Zhou, in his solitary communion with the spirit of heaven and earth, was one of those called 'not departing from the source, known as the celestial person.'" Yet he did not look down upon the myriad things, never harboring disdain or arrogance toward them. "Looking down" is synonymous with "haughty contempt." He did not reproach right or wrong, thus dwelling among the common world. Cheng comments: "Reproach means to blame."

Though his writings were magnificent and wondrous, they flowed in harmony without harm. Shiwen notes: "Magnificent and wondrous: extraordinary. Flowing in harmony: some read as 'joyful,' others as 'following.' It means following things without opposition, hence no harm." Though his words were uneven, their strangeness and marvels were worth beholding. Cheng comments: "Uneven means sometimes empty, sometimes full, not uniform in speech. Strange and marvelous refers to their wit and humor."

His fullness could not be restrained, for his words and principles were so abundant they could not be contained. Above, he roamed with the Creator, and below, he befriended those who stood beyond life and death, beyond beginning and end. In his fundamentals, he was vast and expansive, profound and unconstrained. Xuan comments: "Unconstrained means unrestrained." In his adherence to the source, he may be called harmonious and ascending. Shiwen notes: "Harmonious is read as 'tiao,' also written as 'tiao.'" Case: "Ascending means to reach or attain." Thus, in his devotion to the source, he may be said to have achieved harmony and risen to the heights. Su Yu comments: "This is what the chapter’s outset calls 'not departing from the source.'"

Yet though he responded to transformation and dissolved into things, his principles never exhausted, his presence never faded. Vague and obscure, who could fathom him to the end? Though he adapted to change and dissolved into the nature of things, his function never ceased, his coming never left behind. Dim and indistinct, none could fully grasp his mysteries.

Hui Shi was versatile in many arts—arts meaning methods. His writings filled five carts, a testament to their abundance. Yet his Way was contradictory and variegated. Guo Qingfan notes: "The original text reads 'contradictory' as 'chuan.' The Wen Xuan’s commentary on the 'Wei Du Fu' cites Sima Biao: 'Chuan is read as contradictory. Variegated means mixed and uneven.' The same note appears here, with one variant reading 'contradictory and erratic.' The Fa Yan’s preface states: 'The various masters each let their knowledge run erratic.' The Huainanzi’s 'Chu Zhen Xun' says: 'The two alternate, contradictory and erratic.' The 'Fan Lun Xun' reads: 'Sights and sounds are contradictory and erratic externally.' The 'Shuo Shan Xun' has 'divergent streams run erratic,' cited in the Yu Pian as '● erratic,' with the same meaning." His words never hit the mark. "Hit the mark" is read as "zhong."

The purport of "examining things" is as follows:

It states: "That which examines the purport of events and things has its sayings.

The supremely great has no exterior—this is called the Great Unity.
The supremely small has no interior—this is called the Small Unity.
He fabricated the Small Unity to pair with the Great Unity.

That which has no thickness cannot be accumulated, yet its greatness spans a thousand *li*.

Sima [Biao] notes: 'If it could be accumulated, how would it be limited to a thousand *li*!'

Heaven and earth are base; mountains and marshes are level.
Heaven and earth share a single principle; mountains and marshes are equally balanced.

The sun is just at its zenith even as it inclines; things are just being born even as they die.

Cheng [Xuanying] explains: 'Inclining means to view from the side. Those in the west call it the center, while those in the east call it the side—thus there is no true center or side. It is like life and death: the living take death as death, while the dead take life as death. Since the sun’s center and side are not distinct, so too are life and death without difference.'

The great similarity differs from the small similarity—this is called the small similarity and difference.
To call it great similarity while differing from small similarity is to mix similarity and difference, yet it is still termed the small similarity and difference.

All things are entirely similar and entirely different—this is called the great similarity and difference.
For example, cold and heat, day and night—these are all things entirely similar and entirely different, and thus are called the great similarity and difference.

The southern direction is without limit yet has a limit.
Xuan [Yun] notes: 'To call it "south" already imposes a boundary; this is to take one example and infer the other three.'

Today I go to Yue yet arrived yesterday.
Xuan [Yun] notes: 'Knowing there is a time to reach Yue, the mind has already arrived beforehand.'
Note: This passage also appears in the "Qi Wu Lun" chapter, where "arrived" is written as "reached."

Linked rings can be undone.
Cheng [Xuanying] explains: 'The rings interlock through emptiness, not through the rings themselves. Thus, two rings pass through the void without intersecting, each turning freely—hence they can be undone.'

I know the center of the world: it is north of Yan and south of Yue.
This suggests the earth is suspended in the center, and by land one may reach it—thus north of Yan is south of Yue, differing from Zou Yan’s discourse on the "Ying Sea."

Embrace all things with universal love, for heaven and earth are one body.
Xuan [Yun] notes: 'Heaven and earth are not great, nor am I small.'

Hui Shi took these as a grand vision of the world and used them to enlighten the debaters; the debaters of the world delighted in them.
Hui considered himself uniquely perceptive of the world’s principles and used these to instruct the debaters, who in turn took pleasure in them.

Eggs have feathers.
Xuan [Yun] notes: 'If eggs had no feathers, how would birds come to have them?'

A chicken has three feet.
Sima [Biao] notes: 'A chicken has two feet for walking, but walking is not movement—thus walking arises from the feet, while movement is guided by spirit. Though a chicken has two feet, it requires spirit to walk—hence it is said to have three feet.'

Ying possesses the world.
Xuan [Yun] notes: 'To claim kingship is to aggrandize oneself.'

A dog can be a sheep.
Xuan [Yun] notes: 'The names "dog" and "sheep" are both assigned by people. If one first names a dog as a sheep, then it is a sheep.'

A horse can lay eggs.
Cheng [Xuanying] explains: 'Birth from wombs, eggs, or moisture—these are distinctions made by human sentiment. From the perspective of the Dao, they are not fundamentally different. If bird eggs can have feathers, why should beast wombs not be called eggs?'

A tadpole has a tail.
Cheng [Xuanying] explains: 'The people of Chu call frogs "dingzi." That frogs have no tails is commonly known. Yet from the perspective of the Dao, nothing is truly without form, and what is not without form may still be called "without"—so why should what is not "having" not be named a tail?'
Note: Tadpoles are born without legs but with tails; after hearing thunder, their legs emerge and their tails recede.

Fire is not hot.
Xuan [Yun] notes: 'People all eat cooked food—thus it is not hot.'

Mountains have mouths.
Xuan [Yun] notes: 'Empty valleys transmit sound.'

A wheel does not tread upon the earth.
The wheel turns without ceasing—if it trod upon the earth, how could it turn?

Eyes do not see.
Xuan [Yun] notes: 'If they could see, why do they not illuminate themselves?'

The finger does not reach; what is reached is not exhausted.
Where there is pointing, there is omission—hence it is said the finger does not reach. The second "reached" is likely a scribal error for "ear."
These lines all refer to the human body. Though the ear may cease to hear a sound, ultimately there is no matter in the world, past or present, that is not transmitted—thus it is not exhausted. The character "reached" is a corruption of the above, rendering the passage unintelligible.

A tortoise is longer than a snake.
Cheng [Xuanying] explains: 'Length and shortness are relative—nothing is long that is not also short. To say a snake is long and a tortoise short is a delusion of things. To dispel this confusion, it is said the tortoise is longer than the snake.'
Yu [Yue] notes: 'This is the same idea as "nothing is greater than the tip of an autumn hair, yet Mount Tai is small."'

A square is not square.
Xuan [Yun] notes: 'The world has its own squares, not those made by the carpenter’s square.'

A compass cannot make a circle.
Xuan [Yun] notes: 'The world has its own circles, not those made by the compass.'

A chisel does not encircle the tenon.
Cheng [Xuanying] explains: 'The chisel is the hole; the tenon is the wood within the hole.'
Xuan [Yun] notes: 'The tenon enters of its own accord—the chisel never encircles it.'

The shadow of a flying bird never moves.
Birds fly mostly by day—hence it is said their shadow never moves.
Sima [Biao] cites Mozi: 'The shadow does not shift.'

The swiftness of an arrow’s tip has a moment of neither moving nor resting.
The movement and rest of an arrow’s tip are determined by people. Focusing solely on the arrow’s tip, there is a moment of neither moving nor resting.

A dog is not a canine.
Cheng [Xuanying] explains: 'Dog and canine share the same reality but differ in name. When name and reality align, what they call a dog is what we call a canine; when name and reality diverge, what they call a dog differs from a canine. Mozi said: "A dog is a canine, yet a dog is not a canine."'

A yellow horse and a black ox make three.
Xuan [Yun] notes: 'Two colors and one body make three.'"

The white dog is black.
Xuan [Yun] notes: "White and black are names given by people—how do we know white should not be called black?"

The orphaned colt has never had a mother.
Li [Yi] explains: "A colt is born with a mother, but when called 'orphaned,' it has no mother. Once the name 'orphaned' is established, the name 'mother' is removed—thus the orphaned colt has never had a mother."

A one-foot staff, halved each day, will never be exhausted in ten thousand generations.
Sima [Biao] states: "A *chui* is a staff. If it can be divided, there will always be two parts; if it cannot be divided, one part will always remain—hence it is said it will never be exhausted in ten thousand generations." The debaters used this to engage with Hui Shi, and their arguments remained inexhaustible throughout their lives.

Huan Tuan and Gongsun Long were among the debaters.
Cheng [Xuanying] explains: "Huan and Gongsun were both from Zhao, skilled rhetoricians who served as retainers at the court of Lord Pingyuan. Gongsun Long’s *Discourse on the White Horse* is still circulated in the world."

They adorned people’s minds and altered their intentions.
Cheng [Xuanying] explains: "They embellished people’s thoughts and changed their views."

They could overcome others in speech but could not convince their hearts—this was the debaters’ confinement.
Xuan [Yun] notes: "The debaters were trapped within their own arguments and could not escape."

Hui Shi daily employed his knowledge in debate, matching wits with others—particularly in the arguments of his companions and those he debated with. He uniquely indulged in the bizarre among all the debaters of the world.
Cheng [Xuanying] explains: "*Te* (特) means 'alone'; the character is sometimes written as *jiang* (将)."
Note: *Wei guai* (为怪) refers to displaying strange and eccentric ideas.

This was the essence of his approach.
Yu [Yue] notes: "*Di* (柢) is interchangeable with *di* (氐). In the *Records of the Grand Historian*, *Annals of the First Emperor of Qin*, it says, 'For the most part, they all rebelled against Qin officials'—*Zhengyi* annotates: '*Di* means 'roughly.' Thus, *ci qi di ye* (此其柢也) means 'this is the rough outline of it.'"

Yet Hui Shi’s oral arguments led him to consider himself the most worthy, believing he alone understood principle better than anyone. He would say: "How grand are heaven and earth!"
Sima [Biao] explains: "Hui considered only heaven and earth to be grander than himself."

Hui Shi was aggressive but lacked method.
Sima [Biao] explains: "Hui’s intent was to defeat others, but he had no art of principle."

In the south there was an eccentric named Huang Liao.
*Shiwen* notes: "*Yi* (倚) is sometimes written as *ji* (畸), with the same meaning." Li [Yi] explains: "It means 'unconventional.'"
Cheng [Xuanying] explains: "His surname was Huang, his given name Liao—he did not conform to the common world."

He asked about why heaven and earth do not fall or sink, and the causes of wind, rain, thunder, and lightning. Hui Shi did not decline to answer nor did he ponder—he responded without hesitation.
Cheng [Xuanying] explains: "He did not decline but answered immediately, did not think but replied at once."

He expounded on all things in the world.
Cheng [Xuanying] explains: "He exhaustively explained the origins of all things."

His explanations never ceased, growing ever more numerous, yet he still deemed them insufficient and added even stranger claims.
Cheng [Xuanying] explains: "He augmented his arguments with the bizarre to display his abilities."

He took contrariness as truth and sought to defeat others for fame—thus he could not harmonize with the multitude.
Cheng [Xuanying] explains: "He could not achieve harmony and accord."

Weak in virtue, entangled in things, inwardly frail yet outwardly strong, his path was crooked and obscure.
*Yu* (隩) means "twisted and hidden."

This was not the Great Dao.
Viewing Hui Shi’s abilities through the Dao of heaven and earth, he was like a single gnat’s labor—what use was he to things?

To fulfill the One would suffice.
Xuan [Yun] notes: "Inner sageliness and outer kingship both originate from the One. If this can be fulfilled, why chase after things?"

The more he esteemed himself, the closer he came to the Dao.
*Yue* (曰) is a particle. The more he valued himself without excessive speech, the nearer he was to the Dao.

Yet Hui Shi could not find peace in this. He scattered himself among the ten thousand things without respite.
Cheng [Xuanying] explains: "He dissipated his spirit."

In the end, he was known only for his skill in debate.

What a pity!

Hui Shi’s talent was unbounded yet unfulfilled.
*Tai* (骀) means "unrestrained." *Bu de* (不得) means "without attainment."

He pursued the ten thousand things without return—like chasing an echo with sound, or racing one’s shadow.
Hearing the echo, he raised his voice; he did not know that the louder the sound, the stronger the echo’s resonance. Seeing the shadow approach, he quickened his pace; he did not know that the swifter the form, the more closely the shadow follows.

What sorrow!
*You* (有) was mistakenly written as *ruo* (若) in the original; emended according to the *Jishi* edition.
